6150	THE ILIAD OF HOMER
6150	""
6150	RENDERED INTO ENGLISH BLANK VERSE.
6150	BY
6150	""
6150	EDWARD EARL OF DERBY.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	PREFACE.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	In the spring of 1862 I was induced, at the request of some personal
6150	friends, to print, for private circulation only, a small volume of
6150	"""Translations of Poems Ancient and Modern,"" in which was included the"
6150	first Book of the Iliad. The opinions expressed by some competent
6150	"judges of the degree of success which had attended this ""attempt to"
6150	infuse into an almost literal English version something of the spirit,
6150	"as well as the simplicity, of the great original,"" [Footnote:"
6150	Introduction to unpublished volume.] were sufficiently favourable to
6150	encourage me to continue the work which I had begun. It has afforded
6150	me, in the intervals of more urgent business, an unfailing, and
6150	constantly increasing source of interest; and it is not without a
6150	feeling of regret at the completion of my task, and a sincere
6150	diffidence as to its success, that I venture to submit the result of my
6150	labour to the ordeal of public criticism.
6150	""
6150	Various causes, irrespective of any demerits of the work itself, forbid
6150	me to anticipate for this translation any extensive popularity. First,
6150	I fear that the taste for, and appreciation of, Classical Literature,
6150	are greatly on the decline; next, those who have kept up their
6150	classical studies, and are able to read and enjoy the original, will
6150	hardly take an interest in a mere translation; while the English
6150	reader, unacquainted with Greek, will naturally prefer the harmonious
6150	versification and polished brilliancy of Pope's translation; with
6150	which, as a happy adaptation of the Homeric story to the spirit of
6150	English poetry, I have not the presumption to enter into competition.
6150	But, admirable as it is, Pope's Iliad can hardly be said to be Homer's
6150	Iliad; and there may be some who, having lost the familiarity with the
6150	original language which they once possessed, may, if I have at all
6150	succeeded in my attempt, have recalled to their minds a faint echo of
6150	the strains which delighted their earlier days, and may recognize some
6150	slight trace of the original perfume.
6150	""
6150	Numerous as have been the translators of the Iliad, or of parts of it,
6150	the metres which have been selected have been almost as various: the
6150	ordinary couplet in rhyme, the Spenserian stanza, the Trochaic or
6150	"Ballad metre, all have had their partisans, even to that ""pestilent"
6150	"heresy"" of the so-called English Hexameter; a metre wholly repugnant to"
6150	the genius of our language; which can only be pressed into the service
6150	by a violation of every rule of prosody; and of which, notwithstanding
6150	my respect for the eminent men who have attempted to naturalize it, I
6150	could never read ten lines without being irresistibly reminded of
6150	Canning's
6150	""
6150	"   ""Dactylics call'st thou them? God help thee, silly one!"""
6150	""
6150	But in the progress of this work, I have been more and more confirmed
6150	in the opinion which I expressed at its commencement, that (whatever
6150	"may be the extent of my own individual failure) ""if justice is ever to"
6150	be done to the easy flow and majestic simplicity of the grand old Poet,
6150	"it can only be in the Heroic blank verse."" I have seen isolated"
6150	passages admirably rendered in other metres; and there are many
6150	instances in which a translation line for line and couplet for couplet
6150	naturally suggests itself, and in which it is sometimes difficult to
6150	avoid an involuntary rhyme; but the blank verse appears to me the only
6150	metre capable of adapting itself to all the gradations, if I may use
6150	the term, of the Homeric style; from the finished poetry of the
6150	numerous similes, in which every touch is nature, and nothing is
6150	overcoloured or exaggerated, down to the simple, almost homely, style
6150	of some portions of the narrative. Least of all can any other metre do
6150	full justice to the spirit and freedom of the various speeches, in
6150	which the old warriors give utterance, without disguise or restraint,
6150	to all their strong and genuine emotions. To subject these to the
6150	trammels of couplet and rhyme would be as destructive of their chief
6150	characteristics, as the application of a similar process to the
6150	Paradise Lost of Milton, or the tragedies of Shakespeare; the effect
6150	indeed may be seen by comparing, with some of the noblest speeches of
6150	the latter, the few couplets which he seems to have considered himself
6150	bound by custom to tack on to their close, at the end of a scene or an
6150	act.
6150	""
6150	I have adopted, not without hesitation, the Latin, rather than the
6150	Greek, nomenclature for the Heathen Deities. I have been induced to do
6150	so from the manifest incongruity of confounding the two; and from the
6150	fact that though English readers may be familiar with the names of
6150	Zeus, or Aphrodite, or even Poseidon, those of Hera, or Ares, or
6150	Hephaestus, or Leto, would hardly convey to them a definite
6150	signification.
6150	""
6150	It has been my aim throughout to produce a translation and not a
6150	paraphrase; not indeed such a translation as would satisfy, with regard
6150	to each word, the rigid requirements of accurate scholarship; but such
6150	as would fairly and honestly give the sense and spirit of every
6150	passage, and of every line; omitting nothing, and expanding nothing;
6150	and adhering, as closely as our language will allow, ever to every
6150	epithet which is capable of being translated, and which has, in the
6150	particular passage, anything of a special and distinctive character. Of
6150	the many deficiencies in my execution of this intention, I am but too
6150	conscious; whether I have been in any degree successful, must be left
6150	to the impartial decision of such of the Public as may honour this work
6150	with their perusal.
6150	""
6150	D.
6150	""
6150	KNOWSLEY, OCT., 1864
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	NOTE TO THE FIFTH EDITION.
6150	""
6150	The favourable reception which has been given to the first Editions of
6150	this work, far exceeding my most sanguine hopes, affords a gratifying
6150	proof how far, in my preface, I had overrated the extent to which the
6150	taste for, and appreciation of, Classical Literature had declined. It
6150	will not, I hope, be thought extraordinary that some errors and
6150	inaccuracies should have found their way into a translation executed, I
6150	"must admit, somewhat hastily, and with less of the ""limae labor"" than I"
6150	should have bestowed upon it, had I ventured to anticipate for it so
6150	extensive a circulation. My thanks, therefore, are due to those
6150	critics, who, either publicly or privately, have called my attention to
6150	passages in which the sense of the Author has been either incorrectly
6150	or imperfectly rendered. All of these I have examined, and have availed
6150	myself of several of the suggestions offered for their correction; and
6150	a careful revision of the whole work, and renewed comparison with the
6150	original, have enabled me to discover other defects, the removal of
6150	which will, I hope, render the present Edition, especially in the eyes
6150	of Classical Scholars, somewhat more worthy of the favour which has
6150	been  accorded to its predecessors.
6150	""
6150	D.
6150	""
6150	ST. JAMES'S SQUARE, _May,_ 1885.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""

6150	""
6150	ARGUMENT.
6150	""
6150	THE CONTENTION OF ACHILLES AND AGAMEMNON.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	In the war of Troy, the Greeks having sacked some of the neighbouring
6150	towns, and taken from thence two beautiful captives, Chryseis and
6150	Briseis, allotted the first to Agamemnon, and the last to Achilles.
6150	Chryses, the father of Chryseis, and priest of Apollo, comes to the
6150	Grecian camp to ransom her; with which the action of the poem opens, in
6150	the tenth year of the siege. The priest being refused, and insolently
6150	dismissed by Agamemnon, entreats for vengeance from his god, who
6150	inflicts a pestilence on the Greeks. Achilles calls a council, and
6150	encourages Calchas to declare the cause of it, who attributes it to the
6150	refusal of Chryseis. The King being obliged to send back his captive,
6150	enters into a furious contest with Achilles, which Nestor pacifies;
6150	however, as he had the absolute command of the army, he seizes on
6150	Briseis in revenge. Achilles in discontent withdraws himself and his
6150	forces from the test of the Greeks; and complaining to Thetis, she
6150	supplicates Jupiter to render them sensible of the wrong done to her
6150	son, by giving victory to the Trojans. Jupiter granting her suit,
6150	incenses Juno, between whom the debate runs high, till they are
6150	reconciled by the address of Vulcan.
6150	""
6150	The time of two-and-twenty days is taken up in this book; nine during
6150	the plague, one in the council and quarrel of the Princes, and twelve
6150	for Jupiter's stay among the Ethiopians, at whose return Thetis prefers
6150	her petition. The scene lies in the Grecian camp, then changes to
6150	Chrysa, and lastly to Olympus.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	BOOK I.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	Of Peleus' son, Achilles, sing, O Muse,
6150	The vengeance, deep and deadly; whence to Greece
6150	Unnumbered ills arose; which many a soul
6150	Of mighty warriors to the viewless shades
6150	Untimely sent; they on the battle plain
6150	Unburied lay, a prey to rav'ning dogs,
6150	And carrion birds; but so had Jove decreed,
6150	From that sad day when first in wordy war,
6150	The mighty Agamemnon, King of men,
6150	Confronted stood by Peleus' godlike son.
6150	""
6150	Say then, what God the fatal strife provok'd?
6150	Jove's and Latona's son; he, filled with wrath
6150	Against the King, with deadly pestilence
6150	The camp afflicted,--and the people died,--
6150	For Chryses' sake, his priest, whom Atreus' son
6150	With scorn dismiss'd, when to the Grecian ships
6150	He came, his captive daughter to redeem,
6150	With costly ransom charg'd; and in his hand
6150	The sacred fillet of his God he bore,
6150	And golden staff; to all he sued, but chief
6150	To Atreus' sons, twin captains of the host:
6150	"""Ye sons of Atreus, and ye well-greav'd Greeks,"
6150	May the great Gods, who on Olympus dwell,
6150	Grant you yon hostile city to destroy,
6150	And home return in safety; but my child
6150	Restore, I pray; her proffer'd ransom take,
6150	"And in his priest, the Lord of Light revere."""
6150	""
6150	Then through the ranks assenting murmurs ran,
6150	The priest to rev'rence, and the ransom take:
6150	Not so Atrides; he, with haughty mien,
6150	And bitter speech, the trembling sire address'd:
6150	"""Old man, I warn thee, that beside our ships"
6150	I find thee not, or ling'ring now, or back
6150	Returning; lest thou prove of small avail
6150	Thy golden staff, and fillet of thy God.
6150	Her I release not, till her youth be fled;
6150	Within my walls, in Argos, far from home,
6150	Her lot is cast, domestic cares to ply,
6150	And share a master's bed. For thee, begone!
6150	"Incense me not, lest ill betide thee now."""
6150	""
6150	He said: the old man trembled, and obeyed;
6150	Beside the many-dashing Ocean's shore
6150	Silent he pass'd; and all apart, he pray'd
6150	To great Apollo, fair Latona's son:
6150	""
6150	"""Hear me, God of the silver bow! whose care"
6150	Chrysa surrounds, and Cilia's lovely vale;
6150	Whose sov'reign sway o'er Tenedos extends;
6150	O Smintheus, hear! if e'er my offered gifts
6150	Found favour in thy sight; if e'er to thee
6150	I burn'd the fat of bulls and choicest goats,
6150	Grant me this boon--upon the Grecian host
6150	"Let thine unerring darts avenge my tears."""
6150	""
6150	Thus as he pray'd, his pray'r Apollo heard:
6150	Along Olympus' heights he pass'd, his heart
6150	Burning with wrath; behind his shoulders hung
6150	His bow, and ample quiver; at his back
6150	Rattled the fateful arrows as he mov'd;
6150	Like the night-cloud he pass'd, and from afar
6150	He bent against the ships, and sped the bolt;
6150	And fierce and deadly twang'd the silver bow.
6150	First on the mules and dogs, on man the last,
6150	Was pour'd the arrowy storm; and through the camp,
6150	Constant and num'rous, blaz'd the fun'ral fires.
6150	""
6150	Nine days the heav'nly Archer on the troops
6150	Hurl'd his dread shafts; the tenth, th' assembled Greeks
6150	Achilles call'd to council; so inspir'd
6150	By Juno, white-arm'd Goddess, who beheld
6150	With pitying eyes the wasting hosts of Greece.
6150	When all were met, and closely throng'd around,
6150	Rose the swift-footed chief, and thus began:
6150	""
6150	"""Great son of Atreus, to my mind there seems,"
6150	If we would 'scape from death, one only course,
6150	Home to retrace our steps: since here at once
6150	By war and pestilence our forces waste.
6150	But seek we first some prophet, or some priest,
6150	Or some wise vision-seer (since visions too
6150	From Jove proceed), who may the cause explain,
6150	Which with such deadly wrath Apollo fires:
6150	If for neglected hecatombs or pray'rs
6150	He blame us; or if fat of lambs and goats
6150	"May soothe his anger and the plague assuage."""
6150	""
6150	This said, he sat; and Thestor's son arose,
6150	Calchas, the chief of seers, to whom were known
6150	The present, and the future, and the past;
6150	Who, by his mystic art, Apollo's gift,
6150	Guided to Ilium's shore the Grecian fleet.
6150	Who thus with cautious speech replied, and said;
6150	"""Achilles, lov'd of Heav'n, thou bidd'st me say"
6150	Why thus incens'd the far-destroying King;
6150	Therefore I speak; but promise thou, and swear,
6150	By word and hand, to bear me harmless through.
6150	For well I know my speech must one offend,
6150	The Argive chief, o'er all the Greeks supreme;
6150	And terrible to men of low estate
6150	The anger of a King; for though awhile
6150	He veil his wrath, yet in his bosom pent
6150	It still is nurs'd, until the time arrive;
6150	"Say, then, wilt thou protect me, if I speak?"""
6150	""
6150	Him answer'd thus Achilles, swift of foot:
6150	"""Speak boldly out whate'er thine art can tell;"
6150	For by Apollo's self I swear, whom thou,
6150	O Calchas, serv'st, and who thy words inspires,
6150	That, while I live, and see the light of Heav'n,
6150	Not one of all the Greeks shall dare on thee,
6150	Beside our ships, injurious hands to lay:
6150	No, not if Agamemnon's self were he,
6150	"Who 'mid our warriors boasts the foremost place."""
6150	""
6150	Embolden'd thus, th' unerring prophet spoke:
6150	"""Not for neglected hecatombs or pray'rs,"
6150	But for his priest, whom Agamemnon scorn'd,
6150	Nor took his ransom, nor his child restor'd;
6150	On his account the Far-destroyer sends
6150	This scourge of pestilence, and yet will send;
6150	Nor shall we cease his heavy hand to feel,
6150	Till to her sire we give the bright-ey'd girl,
6150	Unbought, unransom'd, and to Chrysa's shore
6150	A solemn hecatomb despatch; this done,
6150	"The God, appeas'd, his anger may remit."""
6150	""
6150	This said, he sat; and Atreus' godlike son,
6150	The mighty monarch, Agamemnon, rose,
6150	His dark soul fill'd with fury, and his eyes
6150	Flashing like flames of fire; on Calchas first
6150	A with'ring glance he cast, and thus he spoke;
6150	""
6150	"""Prophet of ill! thou never speak'st to me"
6150	But words of evil omen; for thy soul
6150	Delights to augur ill, but aught of good
6150	Thou never yet hast promis'd, nor perform'd.
6150	And now among the Greeks thou spread'st abroad
6150	Thy lying prophecies, that all these ills
6150	Come from the Far-destroyer, for that I
6150	Refus'd the ransom of my lovely prize,
6150	And that I rather chose herself to keep,
6150	To me not less than Clytemnestra dear,
6150	My virgin-wedded wife; nor less adorn'd
6150	In gifts of form, of feature, or of mind.
6150	Yet, if it must he so, I give her back;
6150	I wish my people's safety, not their death.
6150	But seek me out forthwith some other spoil,
6150	Lest empty-handed I alone appear
6150	Of all the Greeks; for this would ill beseem;
6150	"And how I lose my present share, ye see."""
6150	""
6150	To whom Achilles, swift of foot, replied:
6150	"""Haughtiest of men, and greediest of the prey!"
6150	How shall our valiant Greeks for thee seek out
6150	Some other spoil? no common fund have we
6150	Of hoarded treasures; what our arms have won
6150	From captur'd towns, has been already shar'd,
6150	Nor can we now resume th' apportion'd spoil.
6150	Restore the maid, obedient to the God!
6150	And if Heav'n will that we the strong-built walls
6150	Of Troy should raze, our warriors will to thee
6150	"A threefold, fourfold recompense assign."""
6150	""
6150	To whom the monarch Agamemnon thus:
6150	"""Think not, Achilles, valiant though thou art"
6150	In fight, and godlike, to defraud me thus;
6150	Thou shalt not so persuade me, nor o'erreach.
6150	Think'st thou to keep thy portion of the spoil,
6150	While I with empty hands sit humbly down?
6150	The bright-ey'd girl thou bidd'st me to restore;
6150	If then the valiant Greeks for me seek out
6150	Some other spoil, some compensation just,
6150	'Tis well: if not, I with my own right hand
6150	Will from some other chief, from thee perchance,
6150	Or Ajax, or Ulysses, wrest his prey;
6150	And woe to him, on whomsoe'er I call!
6150	But this for future counsel we remit:
6150	Haste we then now our dark-ribb'd bark to launch,
6150	Muster a fitting crew, and place on board
6150	The sacred hecatomb; then last embark
6150	The fair Chryseis; and in chief command
6150	Let some one of our councillors be plac'd,
6150	Ajax, Ulysses, or Idomeneus,
6150	Or thou, the most ambitious of them all,
6150	"That so our rites may soothe the angry God."""
6150	""
6150	To whom Achilles thus with scornful glance;
6150	"""Oh, cloth'd in shamelessness! oh, sordid soul!"
6150	How canst thou hope that any Greek for thee
6150	Will brave the toils of travel or of war?
6150	Well dost thou know that 't was no feud of mine
6150	With Troy's brave sons that brought me here in arms;
6150	They never did me wrong; they never drove
6150	My cattle, or my horses; never sought
6150	In Phthia's fertile, life-sustaining fields
6150	To waste the crops; for wide between us lay
6150	The shadowy mountains and the roaring sea.
6150	With thee, O void of shame! with thee we sail'd,
6150	For Menelaus and for thee, ingrate,
6150	Glory and fame on Trojan crests to win.
6150	All this hast thou forgotten, or despis'd;
6150	And threat'nest now to wrest from me the prize
6150	I labour'd hard to win, and Greeks bestow'd.
6150	Nor does my portion ever equal thine,
6150	When on some populous town our troops have made
6150	Successful war; in the contentious fight
6150	The larger portion of the toil is mine;
6150	But when the day of distribution comes,
6150	Thine is the richest spoil; while I, forsooth,
6150	Must be too well content to bear on board
6150	Some paltry prize for all my warlike toil.
6150	To Phthia now I go; so better far,
6150	To steer my homeward course, and leave thee here
6150	But little like, I deem, dishonouring me,
6150	"To fill thy coffers with the spoils of war."""
6150	""
6150	Whom answer'd Agamemnon, King of men:
6150	"""Fly then, if such thy mind! I ask thee not"
6150	On mine account to stay; others there are
6150	Will guard my honour and avenge my cause:
6150	And chief of all, the Lord of counsel, Jove!
6150	Of all the Heav'n-born Kings, thou art the man
6150	I hate the most; for thou delight'st in nought
6150	But war and strife: thy prowess I allow;
6150	Yet this, remember, is the gift of Heav'n.
6150	Return then, with thy vessels, if thou wilt,
6150	And with thy followers, home; and lord it there
6150	Over thy Myrmidons! I heed thee not!
6150	I care not for thy fury! Hear my threat:
6150	Since Phoebus wrests Chryseis from my arms,
6150	In mine own ship, and with mine own good crew,
6150	Her I send forth; and, in her stead, I mean,
6150	Ev'n from thy tent, myself, to bear thy prize,
6150	The fair Briseis; that henceforth thou know
6150	How far I am thy master; and that, taught
6150	By thine example, others too may fear
6150	"To rival me, and brave me to my face."""
6150	""
6150	Thus while he spake, Achilles chaf'd with rage;
6150	And in his manly breast his heart was torn
6150	With thoughts conflicting--whether from his side
6150	To draw his mighty sword, and thrusting by
6150	Th' assembled throng, to kill th' insulting King;
6150	Or school his soul, and keep his anger down.
6150	But while in mind and spirit thus he mus'd,
6150	And half unsheath'd his sword, from Heav'n came down
6150	Minerva, sent by Juno, white-arm'd Queen,
6150	Whose love and care both chiefs alike enjoy'd.
6150	She stood behind, and by the yellow hair
6150	She held the son of Peleus, visible
6150	To him alone, by all the rest unseen.
6150	Achilles, wond'ring, turn'd, and straight he knew
6150	The blue-eyed Pallas; awful was her glance;
6150	Whom thus the chief with winged words address'd:
6150	""
6150	"""Why com'st thou, child of aegis-bearing Jove?"
6150	To see the arrogance of Atreus' son?
6150	But this I say, and will make good my words,
6150	"This insolence may cost him soon his life."""
6150	""
6150	To whom the blue-ey'd Goddess thus replied:
6150	"""From Heav'n I came, to curb, if thou wilt hear,"
6150	Thy fury; sent by Juno, white-arm'd Queen,
6150	Whose love and care ye both alike enjoy.
6150	Cease, then, these broils, and draw not thus thy sword;
6150	In words, indeed, assail him as thou wilt.
6150	But this I promise, and will make it good,
6150	The time shall come, when for this insolence
6150	A threefold compensation shall be thine;
6150	"Only be sway'd by me, and curb thy wrath."""
6150	""
6150	Whom answer'd thus Achilles, swift of foot:
6150	"""Goddess, I needs must yield to your commands,"
6150	Indignant though I be--for so 'tis best;
6150	"Who hears the Gods, of them his pray'rs are heard."""
6150	""
6150	He said: and on the silver hilt he stay'd
6150	His pow'rful hand, and flung his mighty sword
6150	Back to its scabbard, to Minerva's word
6150	Obedient: she her heav'nward course pursued
6150	To join th' Immortals in th' abode of Jove.
6150	But Peleus' son, with undiminish'd wrath,
6150	Atrides thus with bitter words address'd:
6150	""
6150	"""Thou sot, with eye of dog, and heart of deer!"
6150	Who never dar'st to lead in armed fight
6150	Th' assembled host, nor with a chosen few
6150	To man the secret ambush--for thou fear'st
6150	To look on death--no doubt 'tis easier far,
6150	Girt with thy troops, to plunder of his right
6150	Whoe'er may venture to oppose thy will!
6150	A tyrant King, because thou rul'st o'er slaves!
6150	Were it not so, this insult were thy last.
6150	But this I say, and with an oath confirm,
6150	By this my royal staff, which never more
6150	Shall put forth leaf nor spray, since first it left
6150	Upon the mountain-side its parent stem,
6150	Nor blossom more; since all around the axe
6150	Hath lopp'd both leaf and bark, and now 'tis borne
6150	Emblem of justice, by the sons of Greece,
6150	Who guard the sacred ministry of law
6150	Before the face of Jove! a mighty oath!
6150	The time shall come, when all the sons of Greece
6150	Shall mourn Achilles' loss; and thou the while,
6150	Heart-rent, shalt be all-impotent to aid,
6150	When by the warrior-slayer Hector's hand
6150	Many shall fall; and then thy soul shall mourn
6150	"The slight on Grecia's bravest warrior cast."""
6150	""
6150	Thus spoke Pelides; and upon the ground
6150	He cast his staff, with golden studs emboss'd,
6150	And took his seat; on th' other side, in wrath,
6150	Atrides burn'd; but Nestor interpos'd;
6150	Nestor, the leader of the Pylian host,
6150	The smooth-tongued chief, from whose persuasive lips
6150	Sweeter than honey flowed the stream of speech.
6150	Two generations of the sons of men
6150	For him were past and gone, who with himself
6150	Were born and bred on Pylos' lovely shore,
6150	And o'er the third he now held royal sway.
6150	He thus with prudent words the chiefs address'd:
6150	""
6150	"""Alas, alas! what grief is this for Greece!"
6150	What joy for Priam, and for Priam's sons!
6150	What exultation for the men of Troy,
6150	To hear of feuds 'tween you, of all the Greeks
6150	The first in council, and the first in fight!
6150	Yet, hear my words, I pray; in years, at least,
6150	Ye both must yield to me; and in times past
6150	I liv'd with men, and they despis'd me not,
6150	Abler in counsel, greater than yourselves.
6150	Such men I never saw, and ne'er shall see,
6150	As Pirithous and Dryas, wise and brave,
6150	Coeneus, Exadius, godlike Polypheme,
6150	And Theseus, AEgeus' more than mortal son.
6150	The mightiest they among the sons of men;
6150	The mightiest they, and of the forest beasts
6150	Strove with the mightiest, and their rage subdued.
6150	With them from distant lands, from Pylos' shore
6150	I join'd my forces, and their call obey'd;
6150	With them I play'd my part; with them, not one
6150	Would dare to fight of mortals now on earth.
6150	Yet they my counsels heard, my voice obey'd;
6150	And hear ye also, for my words are wise.
6150	Nor thou, though great thou be, attempt to rob
6150	Achilles of his prize, but let him keep
6150	The spoil assign'd him by the sons of Greece;
6150	Nor thou, Pelides, with the monarch strive
6150	In rivalry; for ne'er to sceptred King
6150	Hath Jove such pow'rs, as to Atrides, giv'n;
6150	And valiant though thou art, and Goddess-born,
6150	Yet mightier he, for wider is his sway.
6150	Atrides, curb thy wrath! while I beseech
6150	Achilles to forbear; in whom the Greeks
6150	"From adverse war their great defender see."""
6150	""
6150	To whom the monarch, Agamemnon, thus:
6150	"""O father, full of wisdom are thy words;"
6150	But this proud chief o'er all would domineer;
6150	O'er all he seeks to rule, o'er all to reign,
6150	To all to dictate; which I will not bear.
6150	Grant that the Gods have giv'n him warlike might,
6150	"Gave they unbridled license to his tongue?"""
6150	""
6150	To whom Achilles, interrupting, thus:
6150	"""Coward and slave indeed I might be deem'd."
6150	Could I submit to make thy word my law;
6150	To others thy commands; seek not to me
6150	To dictate, for I follow thee no more.
6150	But hear me speak, and ponder what I say:
6150	For the fair girl I fight not (since you choose
6150	To take away the prize yourselves bestow'd)
6150	With thee or any one; but of the rest
6150	My dark swift ship contains, against my will
6150	On nought shalt thou, unpunish'd, lay thy hand.
6150	Make trial if thou wilt, that these may know;
6150	"Thy life-blood soon should reek upon my spear."""
6150	""
6150	After this conflict keen of angry speech,
6150	The chiefs arose, the assembly was dispers'd.
6150	""
6150	With his own followers, and Menoetius' son,
6150	Achilles to his tents and ships withdrew.
6150	But Atreus' son launch'd a swift-sailing bark,
6150	With twenty rowers mann'd, and plac'd on board
6150	The sacred hecatomb; then last embark'd
6150	The fair Chryseis, and in chief command
6150	Laertes' son, the sage Ulysses, plac'd.
6150	They swiftly sped along the wat'ry way.
6150	""
6150	Next, proclamation through the camp was made
6150	To purify the host; and in the sea,
6150	Obedient to the word, they purified;
6150	Then to Apollo solemn rites perform'd
6150	With faultless hecatombs of bulls and goats,
6150	Upon the margin of the wat'ry waste;
6150	And, wreath'd in smoke, the savour rose to Heav'n.
6150	""
6150	The camp thus occupied, the King pursued
6150	His threaten'd plan of vengeance; to his side
6150	Calling Talthybius and Eurybates,
6150	Heralds, and faithful followers, thus he spoke:
6150	""
6150	"""Haste to Achilles' tent, and in your hand"
6150	Back with you thence the fair Briseis bring:
6150	If he refuse to send her, I myself
6150	With a sufficient force will bear her thence,
6150	"Which he may find, perchance, the worse for him."""
6150	""
6150	So spake the monarch, and with stern command
6150	Dismiss'd them; with reluctant steps they pass'd
6150	Along the margin of the wat'ry waste,
6150	Till to the tents and ships they came, where lay
6150	The warlike Myrmidons. Their chief they found
6150	Sitting beside his tent and dark-ribb'd ship.
6150	Achilles mark'd their coming, not well pleas'd:
6150	With troubled mien, and awe-struck by the King,
6150	They stood, nor dar'd accost him; but himself
6150	Divin'd their errand, and address'd them thus:
6150	""
6150	"""Welcome, ye messengers of Gods and men,"
6150	Heralds! approach in safety; not with you,
6150	But with Atrides, is my just offence,
6150	Who for the fair Briseis sends you here.
6150	Go, then, Patroclus, bring the maiden forth,
6150	And give her to their hands; but witness ye,
6150	Before the blessed Gods and mortal men,
6150	And to the face of that injurious King,
6150	When he shall need my arm, from shameful rout
6150	To save his followers; blinded by his rage,
6150	He neither heeds experience of the past
6150	Nor scans the future, provident how best
6150	"To guard his fleet and army from the foe."""
6150	""
6150	He spoke: obedient to his friend and chief,
6150	Patroclus led the fair Briseis forth,
6150	And gave her to their hands; they to the ships
6150	Retrac'd their steps, and with them the fair girl
6150	Reluctant went: meanwhile Achilles, plung'd
6150	In bitter grief, from all the band apart,
6150	Upon the margin of the hoary sea
6150	Sat idly gazing on the dark-blue waves;
6150	And to his Goddess-mother long he pray'd,
6150	"With outstretch'd hands, ""Oh, mother! since thy son"
6150	To early death by destiny is doom'd,
6150	I might have hop'd the Thunderer on high,
6150	Olympian Jove, with honour would have crown'd
6150	My little space; but now disgrace is mine;
6150	Since Agamemnon, the wide-ruling King,
6150	"Hath wrested from me, and still holds, my prize."""
6150	""
6150	Weeping, he spoke; his Goddess-mother heard,
6150	Beside her aged father where she sat
6150	In the deep ocean-caves: ascending quick
6150	Through the dark waves, like to a misty cloud,
6150	Beside her son she stood; and as he wept,
6150	She gently touch'd him with her hand, and said,
6150	"""Why weeps my son? and whence his cause of grief?"
6150	"Speak out, that I may hear, and share thy pain."""
6150	""
6150	To whom Achilles, swift of foot, replied,
6150	"Groaning, ""Thou know'st; what boots to tell thee all?"
6150	On Thebes we march'd, Eetion's sacred town,
6150	And storm'd the walls, and hither bore the spoil.
6150	The spoils were fairly by the sons of Greece
6150	Apportion'd out; and to Atrides' share
6150	The beauteous daughter of old Chryses fell.
6150	Chryses, Apollo's priest, to free his child,
6150	Came to th' encampment of the brass-clad Greeks,
6150	With costly ransom charg'd; and in his hand
6150	The sacred fillet of his God he bore,
6150	And golden staff; to all he sued, but chief
6150	To Atreus' sons, twin captains of the host.
6150	Then through the ranks assenting murmurs ran,
6150	The priest to rev'rence, and the ransom take:
6150	Not so Atrides; he, with haughty mien
6150	And bitter words, the trembling sire dismiss'd.
6150	The old man turn'd in sorrow; but his pray'r
6150	Phoebus Apollo heard, who lov'd him well.
6150	Against the Greeks he bent his fatal bow,
6150	And fast the people fell; on ev'ry side
6150	Throughout the camp the heav'nly arrows flew;
6150	A skilful seer at length the cause reveal'd
6150	Why thus incens'd the Archer-God; I then,
6150	The first, gave counsel to appease his wrath.
6150	Whereat Atrides, full of fury, rose,
6150	And utter'd threats, which he hath now fulfill'd.
6150	For Chryses' daughter to her native land
6150	In a swift-sailing ship the keen-ey'd Greeks
6150	Have sent, with costly off'rings to the God:
6150	But her, assign'd me by the sons of Greece,
6150	Brises' fair daughter, from my tent e'en now
6150	The heralds bear away. Then, Goddess, thou,
6150	If thou hast pow'r, protect thine injur'd son.
6150	Fly to Olympus, to the feet of Jove,
6150	And make thy pray'r to him, if on his heart
6150	Thou hast in truth, by word or deed, a claim.
6150	For I remember, in my father's house,
6150	I oft have heard thee boast, how thou, alone
6150	Of all th' Immortals, Saturn's cloud-girt son
6150	Didst shield from foul disgrace, when all the rest,
6150	Juno, and Neptune, and Minerva join'd,
6150	With chains to bind him; then, O Goddess, thou
6150	Didst set him free, invoking to his aid
6150	Him of the hundred arms, whom Briareus
6150	Th' immortal Gods, and men AEgeon call.
6150	He, mightier than his father, took his seat
6150	By Saturn's side, in pride of conscious strength:
6150	Fear seiz'd on all the Gods, nor did they dare
6150	To bind their King: of this remind him now,
6150	And clasp his knees, and supplicate his aid
6150	For Troy's brave warriors, that the routed Greeks
6150	Back to their ships with slaughter may be driv'n;
6150	That all may taste the folly of their King,
6150	And Agamemnon's haughty self may mourn
6150	"The slight on Grecia's bravest warrior cast."""
6150	""
6150	Thus he; and Thetis, weeping, thus replied:
6150	"""Alas, my child, that e'er I gave thee birth!"
6150	Would that beside thy ships thou could'st remain
6150	From grief exempt, and insult! since by fate
6150	Few years are thine, and not a lengthened term;
6150	At once to early death and sorrows doom'd
6150	Beyond the lot of man! in evil hour
6150	I gave thee birth! But to the snow-clad heights
6150	Of great Olympus, to the throne of Jove,
6150	Who wields the thunder, thy complaints I bear.
6150	Thou by thy ships, meanwhile, against the Greeks
6150	Thine anger nurse, and from the fight abstain.
6150	For Jove is to a solemn banquet gone
6150	Beyond the sea, on AEthiopia's shore,
6150	Since yesternight; and with him all the Gods.
6150	On the twelfth day he purpos'd to return
6150	To high Olympus; thither then will I,
6150	And to his feet my supplication make;
6150	"And he, I think, will not deny my suit."""
6150	""
6150	This said, she disappear'd; and left him there
6150	Musing in anger on the lovely form
6150	Tom from his arms by violence away.
6150	""
6150	Meantime, Ulysses, with his sacred freight,
6150	Arriv'd at Chrysa's strand; and when his bark
6150	Had reach'd the shelter of the deep sea bay,
6150	Their sails they furl'd, and lower'd to the hold;
6150	Slack'd the retaining shrouds, and quickly struck
6150	And stow'd away the mast; then with their sweeps
6150	Pull'd for the beach, and cast their anchors out,
6150	And made her fast with cables to the shore.
6150	Then on the shingly breakwater themselves
6150	They landed, and the sacred hecatomb
6150	To great Apollo; and Chryseis last.
6150	Her to the altar straight Ulysses led,
6150	The wise in counsel; in her father's hand
6150	He plac'd the maiden, and address'd him thus:
6150	"""Chryses, from Agamemnon, King of men,"
6150	To thee I come, thy daughter to restore;
6150	And to thy God, upon the Greeks' behalf,
6150	To offer sacrifice, if haply so
6150	We may appease his wrath, who now incens'd
6150	"With grievous suff'ring visits all our host."""
6150	Then to her sire he gave her; he with joy
6150	Receiv'd his child; the sacred hecatomb
6150	Around the well-built altar for the God
6150	In order due they plac'd; their hands then washed,
6150	And the salt cake prepar'd, before them all
6150	With hands uplifted Chryses pray'd aloud:
6150	""
6150	"""Hear me, God of the silver bow! whose care"
6150	Chrysa surrounds, and Cilla's lovely vale,
6150	Whose sov'reign sway o'er Tenedos extends!
6150	Once hast thou heard my pray'r, aveng'd my cause,
6150	And pour'd thy fury on the Grecian host.
6150	Hear yet again, and grant what now I ask;
6150	"Withdraw thy chast'ning hand, and stay the plague."""
6150	""
6150	Thus, as he pray'd, his pray'r Apollo heard.
6150	Their pray'rs concluded, and the salt cake strew'd
6150	Upon the victims' heads, they drew them back,
6150	And slew, and flay'd; then cutting from the thighs
6150	The choicest pieces, and in double layers
6150	O'erspreading them with fat, above them plac'd
6150	The due meat-off'rings; then the aged priest
6150	The cleft wood kindled, and libations pour'd
6150	Of ruddy wine; arm'd with the five-fork'd prongs
6150	Th' attendant ministers beside him stood.
6150	The thighs consum'd with fire, the inward parts
6150	They tasted first; the rest upon the spits
6150	Roasted with care, and from the fire withdrew.
6150	Their labours ended, and the feast prepar'd,
6150	They shared the social meal, nor lacked there aught.
6150	The rage of thirst and hunger satisfied,
6150	Th' attendant youths the flowing goblets crown'd,
6150	And in fit order serv'd the cups to all.
6150	All day they sought the favour of the God,
6150	The glorious paeans chanting, and the praise
6150	Of Phoebus: he, well pleas'd, the strain receiv'd
6150	But when the sun was set, and shades of night
6150	O'erspread the sky, upon the sandy beach
6150	Close to their ship they laid them down to rest.
6150	And when the rosy-finger'd morn appear'd,
6150	Back to the camp they took their homeward way
6150	A fav'ring breeze the Far-destroyer sent:
6150	They stepp'd the mast, and spread the snowy sail:
6150	Full in the midst the bellying sail receiv'd
6150	The gallant breeze; and round the vessel's prow
6150	The dark waves loudly roar'd, as on she rush'd
6150	Skimming the seas, and cut her wat'ry way.
6150	Arriv'd where lay the wide-spread host of Greece,
6150	Their dark-ribb'd vessel on the beach they drew
6150	High on the sand, and strongly shor'd her up;
6150	Then through the camp they took their sev'ral ways.
6150	""
6150	Meantime, beside the ships Achilles sat,
6150	The Heav'n-born son of Peleus, swift of foot,
6150	Chafing with rage repress'd; no more he sought
6150	The honour'd council, nor the battle-field;
6150	But wore his soul away, and inly pin'd
6150	For the fierce joy and tumult of the fight.
6150	But when the twelfth revolving day was come,
6150	Back to Olympus' heights th' immortal Gods,
6150	Jove at their head, together all return'd.
6150	Then Thetis, mindful of her son's request,
6150	Rose from the ocean wave, and sped in haste
6150	To high Olympus, and the courts of Heav'n.
6150	Th' all-seeing son of Saturn there she found
6150	Sitting apart upon the topmost crest
6150	Of many-ridg'd Olympus; at his feet
6150	She sat, and while her left hand clasp'd his knees,
6150	Her right approached his beard, and suppliant thus
6150	She made her pray'r to Saturn's royal son:
6150	""
6150	"""Father, if e'er amid th' immortal Gods"
6150	By word or deed I did thee service true,
6150	Hear now my pray'r! Avenge my hapless son,
6150	Of mortals shortest-liv'd, insulted now
6150	By mighty Agamemnon, King of men,
6150	And plunder'd of his lawful spoils of war.
6150	But Jove, Olympian, Lord of counsel, Thou
6150	Avenge his cause; and give to Trojan arms
6150	Such strength and pow'r, that Greeks may learn how much
6150	"They need my son, and give him honour due."""
6150	""
6150	She said: the Cloud-compeller answer'd not,
6150	But silent sat; then Thetis clasp'd his knees,
6150	And hung about him, and her suit renew'd:
6150	""
6150	"""Give me thy promise sure, thy gracious nod,"
6150	Or else refuse (for thou hast none to fear),
6150	That I may learn, of all th' immortal Gods,
6150	"How far I stand the lowest in thine eyes."""
6150	""
6150	Then, much disturb'd, the Cloud-compeller spoke:
6150	"""Sad work thou mak'st, in bidding me oppose"
6150	My will to Juno's, when her bitter words
6150	Assail me; for full oft amid the Gods
6150	She taunts me, that I aid the Trojan cause.
6150	But thou return, that Juno see thee not,
6150	And leave to me the furth'rance of thy suit.
6150	Lo, to confirm thy faith, I nod my head;
6150	And well among th' immortal Gods is known
6150	The solemn import of that pledge from me:
6150	For ne'er my promise shall deceive, or fail,
6150	"Or be recall'd, if with a nod confirm'd."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and nodded with his shadowy brows;
6150	Wav'd on th' immortal head th' ambrosial locks,
6150	And all Olympus trembled at his nod.
6150	They parted thus: from bright Olympus' heights
6150	The Goddess hasted to her ocean-caves,
6150	Jove to his palace; at his entrance all
6150	Rose from their seats at once; not one presum'd
6150	To wait his coming, but advanc'd to meet.
6150	Then on his throne he sat; but not unmark'd
6150	Of Juno's eye had been the council held
6150	In secret with the silver-footed Queen,
6150	The daughter of the aged Ocean-God;
6150	And with sharp words she thus addressed her Lord:
6150	""
6150	"""Tell me, deceiver, who was she with whom"
6150	Thou late held'st council? ever 'tis thy way
6150	Apart from me to weave thy secret schemes,
6150	"Nor dost thou freely share with me thy mind."""
6150	""
6150	To whom the Sire of Gods and men replied:
6150	"""Expect not, Juno, all my mind to know;"
6150	My wife thou art, yet would such knowledge be
6150	Too much for thee; whate'er I deem it fit
6150	That thou shouldst know, nor God nor man shall hear
6150	Before thee; but what I in secret plan,
6150	"Seek not to know, nor curiously inquire."""
6150	""
6150	Whom answer'd thus the stag-ey'd Queen of Heav'n:
6150	"""What words, dread son of Saturn, dost thou speak?"
6150	Ne'er have I sought, or now, or heretofore,
6150	Thy secret thoughts to know; what thou think'st fit
6150	To tell, I wait thy gracious will to hear.
6150	Yet fear I in my soul thou art beguil'd
6150	By wiles of Thetis, silver-footed Queen,
6150	The daughter of the aged Ocean-God;
6150	For she was with thee early, and embrac'd
6150	Thy knees, and has, I think, thy promise sure,
6150	Thou wilt avenge Achilles' cause, and bring
6150	"Destructive slaughter on the Grecian host."""
6150	""
6150	To whom the Cloud-compeller thus replied:
6150	"""Presumptuous, to thy busy thoughts thou giv'st"
6150	Too free a range, and watchest all I do;
6150	Yet shalt thou not prevail, but rather thus
6150	Be alien'd from my heart--the worse for thee!
6150	If this be so, it is my sov'reign will.
6150	But now, keep silence, and my words obey,
6150	Lest all th' Immortals fail, if I be wroth,
6150	"To rescue thee from my resistless hand."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and terror seiz'd the stag-ey'd Queen:
6150	Silent she sat, curbing her spirit down,
6150	And all the Gods in pitying sorrow mourn'd.
6150	Vulcan, the skill'd artificer, then first
6150	Broke silence, and with soothing words address'd
6150	His mother, Juno, white-arm'd Queen of Heav'n:
6150	"""Sad were't, indeed, and grievous to be borne,"
6150	If for the sake of mortal men you two
6150	Should suffer angry passions to arise,
6150	And kindle broils in Heav'n; so should our feast
6150	By evil influence all its sweetness lack.
6150	Let me advise my mother (and I know
6150	That her own reason will my words approve)
6150	To speak my father fair; lest he again
6150	Reply in anger, and our banquet mar.
6150	For Jove, the lightning's Lord, if such his will,
6150	Might hurl us from our seats (so great his pow'r),
6150	But thou address him still with gentle words;
6150	"So shall his favour soon again be ours."""
6150	""
6150	This said, he rose, and in his mother's hand
6150	A double goblet plac'd, as thus he spoke:
6150	"""Have patience, mother mine! though much enforc'd,"
6150	Restrain thy spirit, lest perchance these eyes,
6150	Dear as thou art, behold thee brought to shame;
6150	And I, though griev'd in heart, be impotent
6150	To save thee; for 'tis hard to strive with Jove.
6150	When to thy succour once before I came,
6150	He seiz'd me by the foot, and hurl'd me down
6150	From Heav'n's high threshold; all the day I fell,
6150	And with the setting sun, on Lemnos' isle
6150	Lighted, scarce half alive; there was I found,
6150	"And by the Sintian people kindly nurs'd."""
6150	""
6150	Thus as he spoke, the white-armed Goddess smil'd,
6150	And, smiling, from, his hand receiv'd the cup,
6150	Then to th' Immortals all, in order due,
6150	He minister'd, and from the flagon pour'd
6150	The luscious nectar; while among the Gods
6150	Rose laughter irrepressible, at sight
6150	Of Vulcan hobbling round the spacious hall.
6150	""
6150	Thus they till sunset pass'd the festive hours;
6150	Nor lack'd the banquet aught to please the sense,
6150	Nor sound of tuneful lyre, by Phoebus touch'd,
6150	Nor Muses' voice, who in alternate strains
6150	Responsive sang: but when the sun had set,
6150	Each to his home departed, where for each
6150	The crippled Vulcan, matchless architect,
6150	With wondrous skill a noble house had rear'd.
6150	""
6150	To his own couch, where he was wont of old,
6150	When overcome by gentle sleep, to rest,
6150	Olympian Jove ascended; there he slept,
6150	And, by his side, the golden-throned Queen.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	ARGUMENT.
6150	""
6150	THE TRIAL OF THE ARMY AND CATALOGUE OF THE FORCES.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	Jupiter, in pursuance of the request of Thetis, sends a deceitful
6150	vision to Agamemnon, persuading him to lead the army to battle in order
6150	to make the Greeks sensible of their want of Achilles. The general, who
6150	is deluded with the hopes of taking Troy without his assistance, but
6150	fears the army was discouraged by his absence and the late plague, as
6150	well as by length of time, contrives to make trial of their disposition
6150	by a stratagem. He first communicates his design to the princes in
6150	council that he would propose a return to the soldiers, and that they
6150	should put a stop to them if the proposal was embraced. Then he
6150	assembles the whole host, and upon moving for a return to Greece, they
6150	unanimously agree to it, and run to prepare the ships. They are
6150	detained by the management of Ulysses, who chastises the insolence of
6150	Thersites. The assembly is recalled, several speeches made on the
6150	occasion, and at length the advice of Nestor followed, which was to
6150	make a general muster of the troops, and to divide them into their
6150	several nations, before they proceeded to battle. This gives occasion
6150	to the poet to enumerate all the forces of the Greeks and Trojans, in a
6150	large catalogue.
6150	""
6150	The time employed in this book consists not entirely of one day. The
6150	scene lies in the Grecian camp and upon the sea-shore; toward the end
6150	it removes to Troy.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	BOOK II.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	All night in sleep repos'd the other Gods,
6150	And helmed warriors; but the eyes of Jove
6150	Sweet slumber held not, pondering in his mind
6150	How to avenge Achilles' cause, and pour
6150	Destructive slaughter on the Grecian host.
6150	Thus as he mus'd, the wisest course appear'd
6150	By a deluding vision to mislead
6150	The son of Atreus; and with winged words
6150	Thus to a phantom form he gave command:
6150	"""Hie thee, deluding Vision, to the camp"
6150	And ships of Greece, to Agamemnon's tent;
6150	There, changing nought, as I command thee, speak.
6150	Bid that he arm in haste the long-hair'd Greeks
6150	To combat; for the wide-built streets of Troy
6150	He now may capture; since th' immortal Gods
6150	Watch over her no longer; all are gain'd
6150	"By Juno's pray'rs; and woes impend o'er Troy."""
6150	""
6150	He said: the Vision heard, and straight obey'd:
6150	Swiftly he sped, and reached the Grecian ships,
6150	And sought the son of Atreus; him he found
6150	Within his tent, wrapped in ambrosial sleep;
6150	Above his head he stood, like Neleus' son,
6150	Nestor, whom Agamemnon rev'renc'd most
6150	Of all the Elders; in his likeness cloth'd
6150	"Thus spoke the heav'nly Vision; ""Sleep'st thou, son"
6150	Of Atreus, valiant warrior, horseman bold?
6150	To sleep all night but ill becomes a chief,
6150	Charg'd with the public weal, and cares of state.
6150	Hear now the words I bear; to thee I come
6150	A messenger from Jove, who from on high
6150	Looks down on thee with eyes of pitying love.
6150	He bids thee arm in haste the long-hair'd Greeks
6150	To combat; since the wide-built streets of Troy
6150	Thou now mayst capture; for th' immortal Gods
6150	Watch over her no longer; all are gain'd
6150	By Juno's pray'rs; and woes impend o'er Troy.
6150	Bear this in mind; and when from sleep arous'd
6150	"Let not my words from thy remembrance fade."""
6150	This said, he vanish'd; and the monarch left,
6150	Inspir'd with thoughts which ne'er should come to pass.
6150	For in that day he vainly hop'd to take
6150	The town of Priam; ignorant what Jove
6150	Design'd in secret, or what woes, what groans,
6150	What lengthen'd labours in the stubborn fight,
6150	Were yet for Trojans and for Greeks in store.
6150	He woke from sleep; but o'er his senses spread
6150	Dwelt still the heavenly voice; he sat upright;
6150	He donn'd his vest of texture fine, new-wrought,
6150	Then o'er it threw his ample robe, and bound
6150	His sandals fair around his well-turn'd feet;
6150	And o'er his shoulders flung his sword, adorn'd
6150	With silver studs; and bearing in his hand
6150	His royal staff, ancestral, to the ships
6150	Where lay the brass-clad warriors, bent his way.
6150	""
6150	Aurora now was rising up the steep
6150	Of great Olympus, to th' immortal Gods
6150	Pure light diffusing; when Atrides bade
6150	The clear-voic'd heralds to th' Assembly call
6150	The gen'ral host; they gave the word, and straight
6150	From ev'ry quarter throng'd the eager crowd.
6150	But first, of all the Elders, by the side
6150	Of Nestor's ship, the aged Pylian chief,
6150	A secret conclave Agamemnon call'd;
6150	And, prudent, thus the chosen few address'd:
6150	"""Hear me, my friends! In the still hours of night"
6150	I saw a heav'nly Vision in my sleep:
6150	Most like it seemed in stature, form, and face
6150	To rev'rend Nestor; at my head it stood,
6150	And with these words address'd me--'Sleep'st thou, son
6150	Of Atreus, valiant warrior, horseman bold?
6150	To sleep all night but ill becomes a chief,
6150	Charg'd with the public weal, and cares of state.
6150	Hear now the words I bear: to thee I come
6150	A messenger from Jove, who from on high
6150	Looks down on thee with eyes of pitying love.
6150	He bids thee arm in haste the long-hair'd Greeks
6150	To combat: since the wide-built streets of Troy
6150	Thou now may'st capture; for th' immortal Gods
6150	Watch over her no longer: all are gain'd
6150	By Juno's pray'rs, and woes impend o'er Troy.
6150	Bear thou my words in mind.' Thus as he spoke
6150	He vanish'd; and sweet sleep forsook mine eyes.
6150	Seek we then straight to arm the sons of Greece:
6150	But first, as is our wont, myself will prove
6150	The spirit of the army; and suggest
6150	Their homeward voyage; ye, throughout the camp
6150	"Restore their courage, and restrain from flight."""
6150	""
6150	Thus having said, he sat; and next arose
6150	Nestor, the chief of Pylos' sandy shore.
6150	Who thus with prudent speech replied, and said:
6150	"""O friends, the chiefs and councillors of Greece,"
6150	If any other had this Vision seen,
6150	We should have deem'd it false, and laugh'd to scorn
6150	The idle tale; but now it hath appear'd,
6150	Of all our army, to the foremost man:
6150	"Seek we then straight to arm the sons of Greece."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and from the council led the way.
6150	Uprose the sceptred monarchs, and obey'd
6150	Their leader's call, and round them throng'd the crowd.
6150	As swarms of bees, that pour in ceaseless stream
6150	From out the crevice of some hollow rock,
6150	Now clust'ring, and anon 'mid vernal flow'rs,
6150	Some here, some there, in busy numbers fly;
6150	So to th' Assembly from their tents and ships
6150	The countless tribes came thronging; in their midst,
6150	By Jove enkindled, Rumour urged them on.
6150	Great was the din; and as the mighty mass
6150	Sat down, the solid earth beneath them groan'd;
6150	Nine heralds rais'd their voices loud, to quell
6150	The storm of tongues, and bade the noisy crowd
6150	Be still, and listen to the Heav'n-born Kings.
6150	""
6150	At length they all were seated, and awhile
6150	Their clamours sank to silence; then uprose
6150	The monarch Agamemnon, in his hand
6150	His royal staff, the work of Vulcan's art;
6150	Which Vulcan to the son of Saturn gave;
6150	To Hermes he, the heav'nly messenger;
6150	Hermes to Pelops, matchless charioteer;
6150	Pelops to Atreus; Atreus at his death
6150	Bequeath'd it to Thyestes, wealthy Lord
6150	Of num'rous herds; to Agamemnon last
6150	Thyestes left it; token of his sway
6150	O'er all the Argive coast, and neighbouring isles.
6150	On this the monarch leant, as thus he spoke:
6150	"""Friends, Grecian Heroes, Ministers of Mars!"
6150	Grievous, and all unlook'd for, is the blow
6150	Which Jove hath dealt me; by his promise led
6150	I hop'd to raze the strong-built walls of Troy,
6150	And home return in safety; but it seems
6150	He falsifies his word, and bids me now
6150	Return to Argos, frustrate of my hope,
6150	Dishonour'd, and with grievous loss of men.
6150	Such now appears th' o'er-ruling sov'reign will
6150	Of Saturn's son; who oft hath sunk the heads
6150	Of many a lofty city in the dust,
6150	And yet will sink; for mighty is his hand.
6150	'Tis shame indeed that future days should hear
6150	How such a force as ours, so great, so brave,
6150	Hath thus been baffled, fighting, as we do,
6150	'Gainst numbers far inferior to our own,
6150	And see no end of all our warlike toil.
6150	For should we choose, on terms of plighted truce,
6150	Trojans and Greeks, to number our array;
6150	Of Trojans, all that dwell within the town,
6150	And we, by tens disposed, to every ten,
6150	To crown our cups, one Trojan should assign,
6150	Full many a ten no cupbearer would find:
6150	So far the sons of Greece outnumber all
6150	That dwell within the town; but to their aid
6150	Bold warriors come from all the cities round,
6150	Who greatly harass me, and render vain
6150	My hope to storm the strong-built walls of Troy.
6150	Already now nine weary years have pass'd;
6150	The timbers of our ships are all decay'd,
6150	The cordage rotted; in our homes the while
6150	Our wives and helpless children sit, in vain
6150	Expecting our return; and still the work,
6150	For which we hither came, remains undone.
6150	Hear then my counsel; let us all agree
6150	Home to direct our course, since here in vain
6150	"We strive to take the well-built walls of Troy."""
6150	""
6150	Thus as he spoke, the crowd, that had not heard
6150	The secret council, by his words was mov'd;
6150	So sway'd and heav'd the multitude, as when
6150	O'er the vast billows of th' Icarian sea
6150	Eurus and Notus from the clouds of Heav'n
6150	Pour forth their fury; or as some deep field
6150	Of wavy corn, when sweeping o'er the plain
6150	The ruffling west wind sways the bending ears;
6150	So was th' Assembly stirr'd; and tow'rd the ships
6150	With clam'rous joy they rush'd; beneath, their feet
6150	Rose clouds of dust, while one to other call'd
6150	To seize the ships and drag them to the main.
6150	"They clear'd the channels, and with shouts of ""home"""
6150	That rose to Heav'n, they knock'd the shores away.
6150	Then had the Greeks in shameful flight withdrawn,
6150	Had Juno not to Pallas thus appeal'd:
6150	"""Oh Heav'n! brave child of aegis-bearing Jove,"
6150	Shall thus the Greeks, in ignominious flight,
6150	O'er the wide sea their homeward course pursue,
6150	And as a trophy to the sons of Troy
6150	The Argive Helen leave, on whose account,
6150	Far from their home, so many valiant Greeks
6150	Have cast their lives away? Go quickly thou
6150	Amid the brass-clad Greeks, and man by man
6150	Address with words persuasive, nor permit
6150	"To launch their well-trimm'd vessels on the deep."""
6150	""
6150	She said, nor did Minerva not obey,
6150	But swift descending from Olympus' heights
6150	With rapid flight she reach'd the Grecian ships.
6150	Laertes' son, in council sage as Jove
6150	There found she standing; he no hand had laid
6150	On his dark vessel, for with bitter grief
6150	His heart was filled; the blue-ey'd Maid approach'd,
6150	"And thus address'd him: ""Great Laertes' son,"
6150	Ulysses, sage in council, can it be
6150	That you, the men of Greece, embarking thus
6150	On your swift ships, in ignominious flight,
6150	O'er the wide sea will take your homeward way,
6150	And as a trophy to the sons of Troy
6150	The Argive Helen leave, on whose account
6150	Far from their homes so many valiant Greeks
6150	Have cast their lives away? Go quickly thou
6150	Among the multitude, and man by man
6150	Address with words persuasive, nor permit
6150	"To launch their well-trimm'd vessels on the deep."""
6150	""
6150	She said; the heav'nly voice Ulysses knew;
6150	Straight, springing to the course, he cast aside,
6150	And to Eurybates of Ithaca,
6150	His herald and attendant, threw his robe;
6150	Then to Atrides hasten'd, and by him
6150	Arm'd with his royal staff ancestral, pass'd
6150	With rapid step amid the ships of Greece.
6150	Each King or leader whom he found he thus
6150	With cheering words encourag'd and restrain'd:
6150	"""O gallant friend, 'tis not for thee to yield,"
6150	Like meaner men, to panic; but thyself
6150	Sit quiet, and the common herd restrain.
6150	Thou know'st not yet Atrides' secret mind:
6150	He tries us now, and may reprove us soon.
6150	His words in council reach'd not all our ears:
6150	See that he work us not some ill; for fierce
6150	His anger; and the Lord of counsel, Jove,
6150	"From whom proceeds all honour, loves him well."""
6150	""
6150	But of the common herd whome'er he found
6150	Clam'ring, he check'd with staff and threat'ning words:
6150	"""Good friend, keep still, and hear what others say,"
6150	Thy betters far: for thou art good for nought,
6150	Of small account in council or in fight.
6150	All are not sovereigns here: ill fares the state
6150	Where many masters rule; let one be Lord,
6150	One King supreme; to whom wise Saturn's son
6150	In token of his sov'reign power hath giv'n
6150	"The sceptre's sway and ministry of law."""
6150	""
6150	Such were his words, as through the ranks he pass'd:
6150	They from the vessels and the tents again
6150	Throng'd to th' Assembly, with such rush of sound,
6150	As when the many-dashing ocean's wave
6150	Breaks on the shore, and foams the frothing sea.
6150	The others all were settled in their seats:
6150	Only Thersites, with unmeasur'd words,
6150	Of which he had good store, to rate the chiefs,
6150	Not over-seemly, but wherewith he thought
6150	To move the crowd to laughter, brawl'd aloud.
6150	The ugliest man was he who came to Troy:
6150	With squinting eyes, and one distorted foot,
6150	His shoulders round, and buried in his breast
6150	His narrow head, with scanty growth of hair.
6150	Against Achilles and Ulysses most
6150	His hate was turn'd; on them his venom pour'd;
6150	Anon, at Agamemnon's self he launch'd
6150	His loud-tongued ribaldry; 'gainst him he knew
6150	Incensed the public mind; and bawling loud,                     [1]
6150	With scurril words, he thus address'd the King:
6150	"""What more, thou son of Atreus, would'st thou have?"
6150	Thy tents are full of brass; and in those tents
6150	Many fair women, whom, from all the spoil,
6150	We Greeks, whene'er some wealthy town we take,
6150	Choose first of all, and set apart for thee.
6150	Or dost thou thirst for gold, which here perchance
6150	Some Trojan brings, the ransom of his son
6150	Captur'd by me, or by some other Greek?
6150	Or some new girl, to gratify thy lust,
6150	Kept for thyself apart? a leader, thou
6150	Shouldst not to evil lead the sons of Greece.
6150	Ye slaves! ye coward souls! Women of Greece!
6150	I will not call you men! why go we not
6150	Home with our ships, and leave this mighty chief
6150	To gloat upon his treasures, and find out
6150	Whether in truth he need our aid, or no;
6150	Who on Achilles, his superior far,
6150	Foul scorn hath cast, and robb'd him of his prize,
6150	Which for himself he keeps? Achilles, sure,
6150	Is not intemperate, but mild of mood;
6150	"Else, Atreus' son, this insult were thy last."""
6150	""
6150	On Agamemnon, leader of the host,
6150	With words like these Thersites pour'd his hate;
6150	But straight Ulysses at his side appear'd,
6150	And spoke, with scornful glance, in stern rebuke:
6150	"""Thou babbling fool, Thersites, prompt of speech,"
6150	Restrain thy tongue, nor singly thus presume
6150	The Kings to slander; thou, the meanest far
6150	Of all that with the Atridae came to Troy.
6150	Ill it beseems, that such an one as thou
6150	Should lift thy voice against the Kings, and rail
6150	With scurril ribaldry, and prate of home.
6150	How these affairs may end, we know not yet;
6150	Nor how, or well or ill, we may return.
6150	Cease then against Atrides, King of men,
6150	To pour thy spite, for that the valiant Greeks
6150	To him, despite thy railing, as of right
6150	An ample portion of the spoils assign.
6150	But this I tell thee, and will make it good,
6150	If e'er I find thee play the fool, as now,
6150	Then may these shoulders cease this head to bear,
6150	And may my son Telemachus no more
6150	Own me his father, if I strip not off
6150	Thy mantle and thy garments, aye, expose
6150	Thy nakedness, and flog thee to the ships
6150	"Howling, and scourg'd with ignominious stripes."""
6150	""
6150	Thus as he spoke, upon Thersites' neck
6150	And back came down his heavy staff; the wretch
6150	Shrank from the blow, and scalding tears let fall.
6150	Where struck the golden-studded staff, appear'd
6150	A bloody weal: Thersites quail'd, and down,
6150	Quiv'ring with pain, he sat, and wip'd away.
6150	With horrible grimace, the trickling tears.
6150	The Greeks, despite their anger, laugh'd aloud,
6150	"And one to other said, ""Good faith, of all"
6150	The many works Ulysses well hath done,
6150	Wise in the council, foremost in the fight,
6150	He ne'er hath done a better, than when now
6150	He makes this scurril babbler hold his peace.
6150	Methinks his headstrong spirit will not soon
6150	"Lead him again to vilify the Kings."""
6150	""
6150	Thus spoke the gen'ral voice: but, staff in hand,
6150	Ulysses rose; Minerva by his side,
6150	In likeness of a herald, bade the crowd
6150	Keep silence, that the Greeks, from first to last,
6150	Might hear his words, and ponder his advice.
6150	He thus with prudent phrase his speech began:
6150	"""Great son of Atreus, on thy name, O King,"
6150	Throughout the world will foul reproach be cast,
6150	If Greeks forget their promise, nor make good
6150	The vow they took to thee, when hitherward
6150	We sailed from Argos' grassy plains, to raze,
6150	Ere our return, the well-built walls of Troy.
6150	But now, like helpless widows, or like babes,
6150	They mourn their cruel fate, and pine for home.
6150	'Tis hard indeed defeated to return;
6150	The seaman murmurs, if from wife and home,
6150	Ev'n for one month, his well-found bark be stay'd,
6150	Toss'd by the wint'ry blasts and stormy sea;
6150	But us the ninth revolving year beholds
6150	Still ling'ring here: I cannot therefore blame
6150	Our valiant Greeks, if by the ships I hear
6150	Their murmurs; yet 'twere surely worst of all
6150	Long to remain, and bootless to return.
6150	Bear up, my friends, remain awhile, and see
6150	If Calchas truly prophesy, or no.
6150	For this ye all have seen, and can yourselves
6150	Bear witness, all who yet are spar'd by fate,
6150	Not long ago, when ships of Greece were met
6150	At Aulis, charg'd with evil freight for Troy,
6150	And we, around a fountain, to the Gods
6150	Our altars rear'd, with faultless hecatombs,
6150	Near a fair plane-tree, where bright water flow'd,
6150	Behold a wonder! by Olympian Jove
6150	Sent forth to light, a snake, with burnish'd scales,
6150	Of aspect fearful, issuing from beneath
6150	The altars, glided to the plane-tree straight.
6150	There, on the topmost bough, beneath the leaves
6150	Cow'ring, a sparrow's callow nestlings lay;
6150	Eight fledglings, and the parent bird the ninth.
6150	All the eight nestlings, utt'ring piercing cries,
6150	The snake devour'd; and as the mother flew,
6150	Lamenting o'er her offspring, round and round,
6150	Uncoiling, caught her, shrieking, by the wing.
6150	Then, when the sparrow's nestlings and herself
6150	The snake had swallowed, by the God, who first
6150	Sent him to light, a miracle was wrought:
6150	For Jove, the deep-designing Saturn's son,
6150	Turn'd him to stone; we stood, and wond'ring gaz'd.
6150	But when this prodigy befell our rites,
6150	Calchas, inspir'd of Heaven, took up his speech:
6150	'Ye long-haired sons of Greece, why stand ye thus
6150	In mute amaze? to us Olympian Jove,
6150	To whom be endless praise, vouchsafes this sign,
6150	Late sent, of late fulfilment: as ye saw
6150	The snake devour the sparrow and her young,
6150	Eight nestlings, and the parent bird the ninth:
6150	So, for so many years, are we condemn'd
6150	To wage a fruitless war; but in the tenth
6150	The wide-built city shall at last be ours.'
6150	Thus he foretold, and now the time is come.
6150	Here then, ye well-greav'd Greeks, let all remain,
6150	"Till Priam's wealthy city be our own."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and loudly cheer'd the Greeks--and loud
6150	From all the hollow ships came back the cheers--
6150	In admiration of Ulysses' speech.
6150	Gerenian Nestor next took up the word:
6150	"""Like children, Grecian warriors, ye debate;"
6150	Like babes to whom unknown are feats of arms.
6150	Where then are now our solemn covenants,
6150	Our plighted oaths? Go, cast we to the fire
6150	Our councils held, our warriors' plans matur'd,
6150	Our absolute pledges, and our hand-plight giv'n,
6150	In which our trust was placed; since thus in vain
6150	In words we wrangle, and how long soe'er
6150	We here remain, solution none we find.
6150	Atrides, thou, as is thy wont, maintain
6150	Unchang'd thy counsel; for the stubborn fight
6150	Array the Greeks; and let perdition seize
6150	Those few, those two or three among the host,
6150	Who hold their separate counsel--(not on them
6150	Depends the issue!)--rather than return
6150	To Argos, ere we prove if Jove indeed
6150	Will falsify his promis'd word, or no.
6150	For well I ween, that on the day when first
6150	We Grecians hitherward our course address'd,
6150	To Troy the messengers of blood and death,
6150	Th' o'er-ruling son of Saturn, on our right
6150	His lightning flashing, with auspicious sign
6150	Assur'd us of his favour; let not then
6150	The thoughts of home be breath'd, ere Trojan wives
6150	Given to our warriors, retribution pay
6150	For wrongs by us, in Helen's cause, sustain'd.
6150	But whoso longs, if such an one there be,
6150	To make his homeward voyage, let him take
6150	His well-rigg'd bark, and go; before the rest
6150	To meet the doom of death! But thou, O King!
6150	Be well advis'd thyself, and others lead
6150	By wholesome counsel; for the words I speak
6150	Are not to be despis'd; by tribes and clans,
6150	O Agamemnon! range thy troops, that so
6150	Tribe may to tribe give aid, and clan to clan.
6150	If thus thou do, and Greeks thy words obey,
6150	Then shalt thou see, of chiefs and troops alike,
6150	The good and bad; for on their own behoof
6150	They all shall fight; and if thou fail, shalt know
6150	Whether thy failure be of Heav'n's decree,
6150	"Or man's default and ignorance of war."""
6150	""
6150	To whom the monarch Agamemnon thus:
6150	"""Father, in council, of the sons of Greece,"
6150	None can compare with thee; and would to Jove
6150	To Pallas, and Apollo, at my side
6150	I had but ten such counsellors as thee!
6150	Then soon should royal Priam's city fall,
6150	Tak'n and destroy'd by our victorious hands.
6150	But now on me hath aegis-bearing Jove,
6150	The son of Saturn, fruitless toil impos'd,
6150	And hurtful quarrels; for in wordy war
6150	About a girl, Achilles and myself
6150	Engag'd; and I, alas! the strife began:
6150	Could we be friends again, delay were none,
6150	How short soe'er, of Ilium's final doom.
6150	But now to breakfast, ere we wage the fight.
6150	Each sharpen well his spear, his shield prepare,
6150	Each to his fiery steeds their forage give,
6150	Each look his chariot o'er, that through the day
6150	We may unwearied stem the tide of war;
6150	For respite none, how short soe'er, shall be
6150	Till night shall bid the storm of battle cease.
6150	With sweat shall reek upon each warrior's breast
6150	The leathern belt beneath the cov'ring shield;
6150	And hands shall ache that wield the pond'rous spear:
6150	With sweat shall reek the fiery steeds that draw
6150	Each warrior's car; but whomsoe'er I find
6150	Loit'ring beside the beaked ships, for him
6150	"'Twere hard to'scape the vultures and the dogs."""
6150	""
6150	He said; and from th' applauding ranks of Greece
6150	Rose a loud sound, as when the ocean wave,
6150	Driv'n by the south wind on some lofty beach,
6150	Dashes against a prominent crag, expos'd
6150	To blasts from every storm that roars around.
6150	Uprising then, and through the camp dispers'd
6150	They took their sev'ral ways, and by their tents
6150	The fires they lighted, and the meal prepar'd;
6150	And each to some one of the Immortal Gods
6150	His off'ring made, that in the coming fight
6150	He might escape the bitter doom of death.
6150	But to the o'erruling son of Saturn, Jove,
6150	A sturdy ox, well-fatten'd, five years old,
6150	Atrides slew; and to the banquet call'd
6150	The aged chiefs and councillors of Greece;
6150	Nestor the first, the King Idomeneus,
6150	The two Ajaces next, and Tydeus' son,
6150	Ulysses sixth, as Jove in council sage.
6150	But uninvited Menelaus came,
6150	Knowing what cares upon his brother press'd.
6150	Around the ox they stood, and on his head
6150	The salt cake sprinkled; then amid them all
6150	The monarch Agamemnon pray'd aloud:
6150	"""Most great, most glorious Jove! who dwell'st on high,"
6150	In clouds and darkness veil'd, grant Thou that ere
6150	This sun shall set, and night o'erspread the earth,
6150	I may the haughty walls of Priam's house
6150	Lay prostrate in the dust; and burn with fire
6150	His lofty gates; and strip from Hector's breast
6150	His sword-rent tunic, while around his corpse
6150	"Many brave comrades, prostrate, bite the dust."""
6150	""
6150	Thus he; but Saturn's son his pray'r denied;
6150	Receiv'd his off'rings, but his toils increas'd.
6150	Their pray'rs concluded, and the salt cake strewed
6150	Upon the victim's head, they drew him back,
6150	And slew, and flay'd; then cutting from the thighs
6150	The choicest pieces, and in double layers
6150	O'erspreading them with fat, above them plac'd
6150	The due meat-off'rings; these they burnt with logs
6150	Of leafless timber; and the inward parts,
6150	First to be tasted, o'er the fire they held.
6150	The thighs consum'd with fire, the inward parts
6150	They tasted first; the rest upon the spits
6150	Roasted with care, and from the fire withdrew.
6150	Their labours ended, and the feast prepar'd,
6150	They shared the social meal, nor lacked there aught.
6150	The rage of thirst and hunger satisfied,
6150	Gerenian Nestor thus his speech began:
6150	"""Most mighty Agamemnon, King of men,"
6150	Great Atreus' son, no longer let us pause,
6150	The work delaying which the pow'rs of Heav'n
6150	Have trusted to our hands; do thou forthwith
6150	Bid that the heralds proclamation make,
6150	And summon through the camp the brass-clad Greeks;
6150	While, in a body, through the wide-spread ranks
6150	"We pass, and stimulate their warlike zeal."""
6150	""
6150	He said; and Agamemnon, King of men,
6150	Obedient to his counsel, gave command
6150	That to the war the clear-voic'd heralds call
6150	The long-hair'd Greeks: they gave the word, and straight
6150	From ev'ry quarter throng'd the eager crowd.
6150	The Heav'n-born Kings, encircling Atreus' son,
6150	The troops inspected: Pallas, blue-ey'd Maid,
6150	Before the chiefs her glorious aegis bore,
6150	By time untouch'd, immortal: all around
6150	A hundred tassels hung, rare works of art,
6150	All gold, each one a hundred oxen's price.
6150	With this the Goddess pass'd along the ranks,
6150	Exciting all; and fix'd in every breast
6150	The firm resolve to wage unwearied war;
6150	And dearer to their hearts than thoughts of home
6150	Or wish'd return, became the battle-field.
6150	""
6150	As when a wasting fire, on mountain tops,
6150	Hath seized the blazing woods, afar is seen
6150	The glaring light; so, as they mov'd, to Heav'n
6150	Flash'd the bright glitter of their burnish'd arms.
6150	""
6150	As when a num'rous flock of birds, or geese,
6150	Or cranes, or long-neck'd swans, on Asian mead,
6150	Beside Cayster's stream, now here, now there,
6150	Disporting, ply their wings; then settle down
6150	With clam'rous noise, that all the mead resounds;
6150	So to Scamander's plain, from tents and ships,
6150	Pour'd forth the countless tribes; the firm earth groan'd
6150	Beneath the tramp of steeds and armed men.
6150	Upon Scamander's flow'ry mead they stood,
6150	Unnumber'd as the vernal leaves and flow'rs.
6150	""
6150	Or as the multitudinous swarms of flies,
6150	That round the cattle-sheds in spring-tide pour,
6150	While the warm milk is frothing in the pail:
6150	So numberless upon the plain, array'd
6150	For Troy's destruction, stood the long-hair'd Greeks.
6150	And as experienced goat-herds, when their flocks
6150	Are mingled in the pasture, portion out
6150	Their sev'ral charges, so the chiefs array'd
6150	Their squadrons for the fight; while in the midst
6150	The mighty monarch Agamemnon mov'd:
6150	His eye, and lofty brow, the counterpart
6150	Of Jove, the Lord of thunder; in his girth
6150	Another Mars, with Neptune's ample chest.
6150	As 'mid the thronging heifers in a herd
6150	Stands, proudly eminent, the lordly bull;
6150	So, by Jove's will, stood eminent that day,
6150	'Mid many heroes, Atreus' godlike son.
6150	""
6150	Say now, ye Nine, who on Olympus dwell,
6150	Muses (for ye are Goddesses, and ye
6150	Were present, and know all things: we ourselves
6150	But hear from Rumour's voice, and nothing know),
6150	Who were the chiefs and mighty Lords of Greece.
6150	But should I seek the multitude to name,
6150	Not if ten tongues were mine, ten mouths to speak,
6150	Voice inexhaustible, and heart of brass,
6150	Should I succeed, unless, Olympian maids,
6150	The progeny of aegis-bearing Jove,
6150	Ye should their names record, who came to Troy.
6150	The chiefs, and all the ships, I now rehearse.
6150	""
6150	Boeotia's troops by Peneleus were led,
6150	And Leitus, and Prothoenor bold,
6150	Arcesilas and Clonius: they who dwelt
6150	In Hyria, and on Aulis' rocky coast,
6150	Scoenus, and Scolus, and the highland range
6150	Of Eteonus; in Thespeia's vale,
6150	Graia, and Mycalessus' wide-spread plains:
6150	And who in Harma and Eilesium dwelt,
6150	And in Erythrae, and in Eleon,
6150	Hyle, and Peteon, and Ocalea,
6150	In Copae, and in Medeon's well-built fort,
6150	Eutresis, Thisbe's dove-frequented woods,
6150	And Coronca, and the grassy meads
6150	Of Haliartus; and Plataea's plain,
6150	In Glissa, and the foot of Lower Thebes,
6150	And in Anchestus, Neptune's sacred grove;
6150	And who in viny-cluster'd Arne dwelt,
6150	And in Mideia, and the lovely site
6150	Of Nissa, and Anthedon's utmost bounds.
6150	With these came fifty vessels; and in each
6150	Were six score youths, Boeotia's noblest flow'r.
6150	""
6150	Who in Aspledon dwelt, and in Minyas' realm
6150	Orehomenus, two sons of Mars obey'd,
6150	Ascalaphus, and bold Ialmenus;
6150	In Actor's house, the son of Azeus, born
6150	Of fair Astyoche, a maiden pure,
6150	Till in the upper chamber, where she slept,
6150	Stout Mars by stealth her virgin bed assail'd:
6150	Of these came thirty ships in order due.
6150	""
6150	By Schedius and Epistrophus, the sons
6150	Of great Iphitus, son of Naubolus,
6150	Were led the Phocian forces; these were they
6150	Who dwelt in Cyparissus, and the rock
6150	Of Python, and on Crissa's lovely plain;
6150	And who in Daulis, and in Panope,
6150	Anemorea and IIyampolis,
6150	And by Cephisus' sacred waters dwelt,
6150	Or in Lilaea, by Cephisus' springs.
6150	In their command came forty dark-ribb'd ships.
6150	These were the leaders of the Phocian bands,
6150	And on Boeotia's left their camp was pitch'd.
6150	""
6150	Ajax, Oileus' son, the Locrians led;
6150	Swift-footed, less than Ajax Telamon,
6150	Of stature low, with linen breastplate arm'd:
6150	But skill'd to throw the spear o'er all who dwell
6150	In Hellas or Achaia: these were they
6150	From Cynos, Opus, and Calliarus,
6150	Bessa, and Scarpha, and Augaea fair,
6150	Tarpha, and Thronium, by Boagrius' stream.
6150	Him from beyond Euboea's sacred isle,
6150	Of Locrians follow'd forty dark-ribb'd ships.
6150	""
6150	Breathing firm courage high, th' Abantian host,
6150	Who from Euboea and from Chalcis came,
6150	Or who in vine-clad Histiaea dwelt,
6150	Eretria, and Cerinthus maritime,
6150	And who the lofty fort of Dium held,
6150	And in Carystus and in Styra dwelt:
6150	These Elephenor led, true plant of Mars,
6150	Chalcodon's son, the brave Abantian chief.
6150	Him, all conspicuous with their long black hair,
6150	The bold Abantians follow'd: spearmen skill'd,
6150	Who through the foemen's breastplates knew full well,
6150	Held in firm grasp, to drive the ashen spear.
6150	In his command came forty dark-ribb'd ships.
6150	""
6150	Those who in Athens' well-built city dwelt,
6150	The noble-soul'd Erectheus' heritage;
6150	Child of the fertile soil, by Pallas rear'd,
6150	Daughter of Jove, who him in Athens plac'd
6150	In her own wealthy temple; there with blood
6150	Of bulls and lambs, at each revolving year,
6150	The youths of Athens do him sacrifice;
6150	These by Menestheus, Peteus' son, were led.
6150	With him might none of mortal men compare,
6150	In order due of battle to array
6150	Chariots and buckler'd men; Nestor alone
6150	Perchance might rival him, his elder far.
6150	In his command came fifty dark-ribb'd ships.
6150	""
6150	Twelve ships from Salamis with Ajax came,
6150	And they beside th' Athenian troops were rang'd.
6150	""
6150	Those who from Argos, and the well-wall'd town
6150	Of Tyrins came, and from Hermione,
6150	And Asine, deep-bosom'd in the bay;
6150	And from Troezene and Eione,
6150	And vine-clad Epidaurus; and the youths
6150	Who dwelt in Mases, and AEgina's isle;
6150	O'er all of these the valiant Diomed
6150	Held rule; and Sthenelus, th' illustrious son
6150	Of far-fam'd Capaneus; with these, the third,
6150	A godlike warrior came, Euryalus,
6150	Son of Mecistheus, Talaus' royal son.
6150	Supreme o'er all was valiant Diomed.
6150	In their command came eighty dark-ribb'd ships.
6150	""
6150	Who in Mycenae's well-built fortress dwelt,
6150	And wealthy Corinth, and Cleone fair,
6150	Orneia, and divine Araethure,
6150	And Sicyon, where Adrastus reign'd of old,
6150	And Gonoessa's promontory steep,
6150	And Hyperesia, and Pellene's rock;
6150	In AEgium, and the scatter'd towns that he
6150	Along the beach, and wide-spread Helice;
6150	Of these a hundred ships obey'd the rule
6150	Of mighty Agamemnon, Atreus' son.
6150	The largest and the bravest host was his;
6150	And he himself, in dazzling armour clad,
6150	O'er all the heroes proudly eminent,
6150	Went forth exulting in his high estate,
6150	Lord of the largest host, and chief of chiefs.
6150	""
6150	Those who in Lacedaemon's lowland plains,
6150	And who in Sparta and in Phare dwelt,
6150	And who on Messa's dove-frequented cliffs,
6150	Bryseia, and AEgaea's lovely vale,
6150	And in Amyclae, and the sea-bathed fort
6150	Of Helos, OEtylus and Laas dwelt;
6150	His valiant brother Menelaus led,
