6150	With sixty ships; but ranged apart they lay.
6150	Their chief, himself in martial ardour bold,
6150	Inspiring others, fill'd with fierce desire
6150	The rape of Helen and his wrongs to avenge.
6150	""
6150	They who in Pylos and Arene dwelt,
6150	And Thyrum, by the ford of Alpheus' stream,
6150	In Cyparissus and Amphigene,
6150	Pteleon, and lofty OEpus' well-built fort,
6150	Helos, and Dorium, where the Muses met,
6150	And put to silence Thracian Thamyris,
6150	As from OEchalia, from the royal house
6150	Of Eurytus he came; he, over-bold,
6150	Boasted himself pre-eminent in song,
6150	Ev'n though the daughters of Olympian Jove,
6150	The Muses, were his rivals: they in wrath
6150	Him of his sight at once and powr'r of song
6150	Amerc'd, and bade his hand forget the lyre.
6150	These by Gerenian Nestor all were led,
6150	In fourscore ships and ten in order due.
6150	""
6150	They of Arcadia, and the realm that lies
6150	Beneath Cyllene's mountain high, around
6150	The tomb of AEpytus, a warrior race;
6150	The men of Pheneus and Orchomenus
6150	In flocks abounding; who in Ripa dwelt,
6150	In Stratia, and Enispe's breezy height,
6150	Or Tegea held, and sweet Mantinea,
6150	Stymphalus and Parrhasia; these were led
6150	By Agapenor brave, Anchaeus' son,
6150	In sixty ships; in each a num'rous crew
6150	Of stout Arcadian youths, to war inur'd.
6150	The ships, wherewith they crossed the dark-blue sea,
6150	Were giv'n by Agamemnon, King of men,
6150	The son of Atreus; for th' Arcadian youth
6150	Had ne'er to maritime pursuits been train'd.
6150	""
6150	Who in Buprasium and in Elis dwelt,
6150	Far as Hyrmine, and th' extremest bounds
6150	Of Myrsinus; and all the realm that lies
6150	Between Aleisium and the Olenian rock;
6150	These by four chiefs were led; and ten swift ships,
6150	By bold Epeians mann'd, each chief obey'd.
6150	Amphimachus and Thalpius were the first,
6150	Sons of two brothers, Cteatus the one,
6150	The other Eurytus, to Actor born;
6150	Next Amarynceus' son, Diores bold;
6150	The fourth Polyxenus, the godlike son
6150	Of Augeas' royal heir, Agasthenes.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	They of Dulichium, and the sacred isles,
6150	Th' Echinades, which face, from o'er the sea,
6150	The coast of Elis, were by Meges led,
6150	The son of Phyleus, dear to Jove, in arms
6150	Valiant as Mars; who, with his sire at feud,
6150	Had left his home, and to Dulichium come:
6150	In his command were forty dark-ribb'd ships.
6150	""
6150	Those who from warlike Cephalonia came,
6150	And Ithaca, and leafy Neritus,
6150	And Crocyleium; rugged AEgilips,
6150	And Samos, and Zacynthus, and the coast
6150	Of the mainland with its opposing isles;
6150	These in twelve ships, with scarlet-painted bows,
6150	Ulysses led, in council sage as Jove.
6150	""
6150	Thoas, Andraemon's son, th' AEtolians led;
6150	From Pleuron, and Pylone, Olenus,
6150	Chalcis-by-sea, and rocky Calydon:
6150	The race of OEneus was no more; himself,
6150	And fair-hair'd Meleager, both were dead:
6150	Whence all AEtolia's rule on him was laid.
6150	In his command came forty dark-ribb'd ships.
6150	""
6150	The King Idomeneus the Cretans led,
6150	From Cnossus, and Gortyna's well-wall'd town,
6150	Miletus, and Lycastus' white-stone cliffs,
6150	Lyctus, and Phaestus, Rhytium, and the rest
6150	Whom Crete from all her hundred cities sent:
6150	These all Idomeneus, a spearman skill'd,
6150	Their King, commanded; and Meriones,
6150	In battle terrible as blood-stain'd Mars.
6150	In their command came fourscore dark-ribb'd ships.
6150	""
6150	Valiant and tall, the son of Hercules,
6150	Tlepolemus, nine vessels brought from Rhodes,
6150	By gallant Rhodians mann'd, who tripartite
6150	Were settled, and in Ialyssus dwelt,
6150	In Lindus, and Cameirus' white-stone hills.
6150	These all renown'd Tlepolemus obey'd,
6150	Who to the might of Hercules was born
6150	Of fair Astyoche; his captive she,
6150	When many a goodly town his arms had raz'd,
6150	Was brought from Ephyra, by Selles' stream.
6150	Rear'd in the royal house, Tlepolemus,
6150	In early youth, his father's uncle slew,
6150	A warrior once, but now in life's decline,
6150	Lycimnius; then in haste a fleet he built,
6150	Muster'd a num'rous host; and fled, by sea,
6150	The threaten'd vengeance of the other sons
6150	And grandsons of the might of Hercules.
6150	Long wand'rings past, and toils and perils borne,
6150	To Rhodes he came; his followers, by their tribes,
6150	Three districts form'd; and so divided, dwelt,
6150	Belov'd of Jove, the King of Gods and men,
6150	Who show'r'd upon them boundless store of wealth.
6150	""
6150	Nireus three well-trimm'd ships from Syme brought;
6150	Nireus, to Charops whom Aglaia bore;
6150	Nireus, the goodliest man of all the Greeks,
6150	Who came to Troy, save Peleus' matchless son:
6150	But scant his fame, and few the troops he led.
6150	""
6150	Who in Nisyrus dwelt, and Carpathus,
6150	And Cos, the fortress of Eurypylus,
6150	And in the Casian and Calydnian Isles,
6150	Were by Phidippus led, and Antiphus,
6150	Two sons of Thessalus, Alcides' son;
6150	With them came thirty ships in order due.
6150	""
6150	Next those who in Pelasgian Argos dwelt,
6150	And who in Alos, and in Alope,
6150	Trachys, and Phthia, and in Hellas fam'd
6150	For women fair; of these, by various names,
6150	Achaians, Myrmidons, Hellenes, known,
6150	In fifty ships, Achilles was the chief.
6150	But from the battle-strife these all abstain'd,
6150	Since none there was to marshal their array.
6150	For Peleus' godlike son, the swift of foot,
6150	Lay idly in his tent, the loss resenting
6150	Of Brises' fair-hair'd daughter; whom himself
6150	Had chosen, prize of all his warlike toil,
6150	When he Lyrnessus and the walls of Thebes
6150	O'erthrew, and Mynes and Epistrophus
6150	Struck down, bold warriors both, Evenus' sons,
6150	Selepius' royal heir; for her in wrath,
6150	He held aloof, but soon again to appear.
6150	""
6150	Those in the flow'ry plain of Pyrrhasus,
6150	To Ceres dear, who dwelt; in Phylace,
6150	In Iton, rich in flocks, and, by the sea,
6150	In Antron, and in Pteleon's grass-clad meads;
6150	These led Protesilaus, famed in arms,
6150	While yet he liv'd; now laid beneath the sod.
6150	In Phylace were left his weeping wife,
6150	And half-built house; him, springing to the shore,
6150	First of the Greeks, a Dardan warrior slew.
6150	Nor were his troops, their leader though they mourn'd,
6150	Left leaderless; the post of high command
6150	Podarces claim'd of right, true plant of Mars,
6150	Iphiclus' son, the rich Phylacides;
6150	The brother of Protesilaus he,
6150	Younger in years, nor equal in renown;
6150	Yet of a chief no want the forces felt,
6150	Though much they mourn'd their valiant leader slain.
6150	In his command came forty dark-ribb'd ships.
6150	""
6150	Those who from Pherae came, beside the lake
6150	Boebeis, and who dwelt in Glaphyrae,
6150	In Boebe, and Iolcos' well-built fort,
6150	These in eleven ships Eumelus led,
6150	Whom Pelias' daughter, fairest of her race,
6150	Divine Alcestis to Admetus bore.
6150	""
6150	Who in Methone and Thaumacia dwelt,
6150	In Meliboea and Olizon's rock;
6150	These Philoctetes, skilful archer, led.
6150	Sev'n ships were theirs, and ev'ry ship was mann'd
6150	By fifty rowers, skilful archers all.
6150	But he, their chief, was lying, rack'd with pain,
6150	On Lemnos' sacred isle; there left perforce
6150	In torture from a venomous serpent's wound:
6150	There he in anguish lay: nor long, ere Greeks
6150	Of royal Philoctetes felt their need.
6150	Yet were his troops, their leader though they mourn'd,
6150	Not leaderless: Oileus' bastard son,
6150	Medon, of Rhene born, their ranks array'd.
6150	""
6150	Who in OEchalia, Eurytus' domain,
6150	In Tricca, and in rough Ithome dwelt,
6150	These Podalirius and Machaon led,
6150	Two skilful leeches, AEsculapius' sons.
6150	Of these came thirty ships in order due.
6150	""
6150	Who in Ormenium and Asterium dwelt,
6150	By Hypereia's fount, and on the heights
6150	Of Titanum's white peaks, of these was chief
6150	Eurypylus, Euaemon's gallant son;
6150	In his command came forty dark-ribb'd ships.
6150	""
6150	Who in Argissa and Gyrtona dwelt,
6150	Ortha, Elone, and the white-wall'd town
6150	Of Oloosson, Polypoetes led;
6150	Son of Pirithous, progeny of Jove,
6150	A warrior bold; Hippodamia fair
6150	Him to Pirithous bore, what time he slew
6150	The shaggy Centaurs, and from Pelion's heights
6150	For refuge 'mid the rude AEthices drove.
6150	Nor he alone; with him to Troy there came
6150	A scion true of Mars, Leonteus, heir
6150	Of nobly-born Coronus, Caeneus' son.
6150	In their command came forty dark-ribb'd ships.
6150	""
6150	With two and twenty vessels Gouneus came
6150	From Cythus; he the Enienes led,
6150	And the Peraebians' warlike tribes, and those
6150	Who dwelt around Dodona's wintry heights,
6150	Or till'd the soil upon the lovely banks
6150	Of Titaresius, who to Peneus pours
6150	The tribute of his clearly-flowing stream;
6150	Yet mingles not with Peneus' silver waves,
6150	But on the surface floats like oil, his source
6150	From Styx deriving, in whose awful name
6150	Both Gods and men by holiest oaths are bound.
6150	""
6150	Magnesia's troops, who dwelt by Peneus' stream,
6150	Or beneath Pelion's leafy-quiv'ring shades,
6150	Swift-footed Prothous led, Tenthredon's son;
6150	In his command came forty dark-ribb'd ships.
6150	""
6150	These were the leaders and the chiefs of Greece:
6150	Say, Muse, of these, who with th' Atridae came,
6150	Horses and men, who claim'd the highest praise.
6150	Of steeds, the bravest and the noblest far
6150	Were those Eumelus drove, Admetus' son:
6150	Both swift as birds, in age and colour match'd,
6150	Alike in height, as measur'd o'er the back;
6150	Both mares, by Phoebus of the silver bow
6150	Rear'd in Pieria, thunderbolts of war.
6150	Of men, while yet Achilles held his wrath,
6150	The mightiest far was Ajax Telamon.
6150	For with Achilles, and the steeds that bore
6150	The matchless son of Peleus, none might vie:
6150	But 'mid his beaked ocean-going ships
6150	He lay, with Agamemnon, Atreus' son,
6150	Indignant; while his troops upon the beach
6150	With quoits and jav'lins whil'd away the day,
6150	And feats of archery; their steeds the while
6150	The lotus-grass and marsh-grown parsley cropp'd,
6150	Each standing near their car; the well-wrought cars
6150	Lay all unheeded in the warriors' tents;
6150	They, inly pining for their godlike chief,
6150	Roam'd listless up and down, nor join'd the fray.
6150	""
6150	Such was the host, which, like devouring fire,
6150	O'erspread the land; the earth beneath them groan'd:
6150	As when the Lord of thunder, in his wrath,
6150	The earth's foundations shakes, in Arimi,
6150	Where, buried deep, 'tis said, Typhoeus lies;
6150	So at their coming, groan'd beneath their feet
6150	The earth, as quickly o'er the plain they spread.
6150	""
6150	To Troy, sent down by aegis-bearing Jove,
6150	With direful tidings storm-swift Iris came.
6150	At Priam's gate, in solemn conclave met,
6150	Were gather'd all the Trojans, young and old:
6150	Swift Iris stood amidst them, and, the voice
6150	Assuming of Polites, Priam's son,
6150	The Trojan scout, who, trusting to his speed,
6150	Was posted on the summit of the mound
6150	Of ancient AEsuetes, there to watch
6150	Till from their ships the Grecian troops should march;
6150	His voice assuming, thus the Goddess spoke:
6150	"""Old man, as erst in peace, so still thou lov'st"
6150	The strife of words; but fearful war is nigh.
6150	Full many a host in line of battle rang'd
6150	My eyes have seen; but such a force as this,
6150	So mighty and so vast, I ne'er beheld:
6150	In number as the leaves, or as the sand,
6150	Against the city o'er the plain they come.
6150	Then, Hector, for to thee I chiefly speak,
6150	This do; thou know'st how various our allies,
6150	Of diff'rent nations and discordant tongues:
6150	Let each then those command o'er whom he reigns,
6150	"And his own countrymen in arms array."""
6150	She said; and Hector knew the voice divine,
6150	And all, dissolv'd the council, flew to arms,
6150	The gates were open'd wide; forth pour'd the crowd,
6150	Both foot and horse; and loud the tumult rose.
6150	""
6150	Before the city stands a lofty mound,
6150	In the mid plain, by open space enclos'd;
6150	Men call it Batiaea; but the Gods
6150	The tomb of swift Myrinna; muster'd there
6150	The Trojans and Allies their troops array'd.
6150	""
6150	The mighty Hector of the glancing helm,
6150	The son of Priam, led the Trojan host:
6150	The largest and the bravest band were they,
6150	Bold spearmen all, who follow'd him in arms.
6150	""
6150	Anchises' valiant son, AEneas, led
6150	The Dardans; him, 'mid Ida's jutting peaks,
6150	Immortal Venus to Anchises bore,
6150	A Goddess yielding to a mortal's love:
6150	With him, well skill'd in war, Archilochus
6150	And Acamas, Antenor's gallant sons.
6150	""
6150	Who in Zeleia dwelt, at Ida's foot,
6150	Of Trojan race, a wealthy tribe, who drank
6150	Of dark AEsepus' waters, these were led
6150	By Pandarus, Lycaon's noble son,
6150	Taught by Apollo's self to draw the bow.
6150	""
6150	Who from Adraste, and Apaesus' realm,
6150	From Pityeia, and the lofty hill
6150	Tereian came, with linen corslets girt,
6150	Adrastus and Amphius led; two sons
6150	Of Merops of Percote; deeply vers'd
6150	Was he in prophecy; and from the war
6150	Would fain have kept his sons; but they, by fate,
6150	Doom'd to impending death, his caution scorn'd.
6150	""
6150	Those who from Practium and Percote came,
6150	And who in Sestos and Abydos dwelt,
6150	And in Arisba fair; those Asius led,
6150	The son of Hyrtacus, of heroes chief;
6150	Asius the son of Hyrtacus, who came
6150	From fair Arisba, borne by fiery steeds
6150	Of matchless size and strength, from Selles' stream.
6150	""
6150	Hippothous led the bold Pelasgian tribes,
6150	Who dwell in rich Larissa's fertile soil,
6150	Hippothous and Pylaeus, Lethus' sons,
6150	The son of Teutamus, Pelasgian chief.
6150	""
6150	The Thracians, by fast-flowing Hellespont
6150	Encompass'd, Acamas and Peirous brave;
6150	The spear-skill'd Cicones Euphemus led,
6150	Son of Troezenus, Ceus' highborn son.
6150	""
6150	From distant Amydon Pyraecmes brought
6150	The Paeon archers from broad Axius' banks;
6150	Axius, the brightest stream on earth that flows.
6150	""
6150	The hairy strength of great Pylaemenes
6150	The Paphlagonians led from Eneti
6150	(Whence first appear'd the stubborn race of mules),
6150	Who in Cytorus and in Sesamum,
6150	And round Parthenius' waters had their home;
6150	Who dwelt in Cromne, and AEgialus,
6150	And on the lofty Erythinian rock.
6150	""
6150	By Hodius and Epistrophus were brought
6150	From distant Alybe, the wealthy source
6150	Of silver ore, the Alizonian bands.
6150	""
6150	Chromis the Mysians led, and Ennomus;
6150	A skilful augur, but his augury
6150	From gloomy death to save him nought avail'd;
6150	Slain by the son of Peleus, in the stream,
6150	Where many another Trojan felt his arm.
6150	""
6150	From far Ascania's lake, with Phorcys join'd,
6150	The godlike presence of Ascanius brought
6150	The Phrygians, dauntless in the standing fight.
6150	""
6150	From Lydia came Pylaemenes' two sons,
6150	Born of the lake Gygeian; Antiphus,
6150	And Mesthles; these Maeonia's forces led,
6150	Who dwelt around the foot of Tmolus' hill.
6150	""
6150	In charge of Nastes came the Carian troops,
6150	Of barbarous speech; who in Miletus dwelt,
6150	And in the dense entangled forest shade
6150	Of Phthira's hill, and on the lofty ridge
6150	Of Mycale, and by Maeander's stream;
6150	These came with Nastes and Amphimacus;
6150	Amphimacus and Nastes, Nomion's sons;
6150	With childish folly to the war he came,
6150	Laden with store of gold; yet nought avail'd
6150	His gold to save him from the doom of death;
6150	Slain by the son of Peleus in the stream;
6150	And all his wealth Achilles bore away.
6150	""
6150	Sarpedon last, and valiant Glaucus led
6150	The Lycian bands, from distant Lycia's shore,
6150	Beside the banks of Xanthus' eddying stream.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	ARGUMENT.
6150	""
6150	THE DUEL OF MENELAUS AND PARIS.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	The armies being ready to engage, a single combat is agreed upon,
6150	between Menelaus and Paris (by the intervention of Hector) for the
6150	determination of the war. Iris is sent to call Helen to behold the
6150	fight. She leads her to the walls of Troy, where Priam sat with his
6150	counsellors, observing the Grecian leaders on the plain below, to whom
6150	Helen gives an account of the chief of them. The kings on either part
6150	take the solemn oath for the conditions of the combat. The duel ensues,
6150	wherein Paris being overcome, is snatched away in a cloud by Venus, and
6150	transported to his apartment. She then calls Helen from the walls, and
6150	brings the lovers together. Agamemnon, on the part of the Grecians,
6150	demands the restoration of Helen, and the performance of the articles.
6150	""
6150	The three-and-twentieth day still continues throughout this book. The
6150	scene is sometimes in the field before Troy, and sometimes in Troy
6150	itself.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	BOOK III.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	WHEN by their sev'ral chiefs the troops were rang'd,
6150	With noise and clamour, as a flight of birds,
6150	The men of Troy advanc'd; as when the cranes,
6150	Flying the wintry storms, send forth on high
6150	Their dissonant clamours, while o'er the ocean stream
6150	They steer their course, and on their pinions bear
6150	Battle and death to the Pygmaean race.
6150	""
6150	On th' other side the Greeks in silence mov'd,
6150	Breathing firm courage, bent on mutual aid.
6150	As when the south wind o'er the mountain tops
6150	Spreads a thick veil of mist, the shepherd's bane,
6150	And friendly to the nightly thief alone,
6150	That a stone's throw the range of vision bounds;
6150	So rose the dust-cloud, as in serried ranks
6150	With rapid step they mov'd across the plain.
6150	But when th' opposing forces near were met,
6150	A panther's skin across his shoulders flung,
6150	Arm'd with his bow and sword, in front of all
6150	Advanc'd the godlike Paris; in his hand
6150	He pois'd two brass-tipp'd jav'lins, and defied
6150	To mortal combat all the chiefs of Greece.
6150	""
6150	Him when the warlike Menelaus saw
6150	With haughty strides advancing from the crowd;
6150	As when a lion, hunger-pinch'd, espies
6150	Some mighty beast of chase, or antler'd stag,
6150	Or mountain goat, and with exulting spring
6150	Strikes down his prey, and on the carcase feeds,
6150	Unscar'd by baying hounds and eager youths:
6150	So Menelaus saw with fierce delight
6150	The godlike Paris; for he deem'd that now
6150	His vengeance was at hand; and from his car,
6150	Arm'd as he was, he leap'd upon the plain.
6150	But when the godlike Paris saw him spring
6150	Defiant from the ranks, with quailing heart,
6150	Back to his comrades' shelt'ring crowd he sprang,
6150	In fear of death; as when some trav'ller spies,
6150	Coil'd in his path upon the mountain side,
6150	A deadly snake, back he recoils in haste,
6150	His limbs all trembling, and his cheek all pale;
6150	So back recoil'd, in fear of Atreus' son,
6150	The godlike Paris 'mid the Trojan host.
6150	""
6150	To whom in stern rebuke thus Hector spoke:
6150	"""Thou wretched Paris, though in form so fair,"
6150	Thou slave of woman, manhood's counterfeit!
6150	Would thou hadst ne'er been born, or died at least
6150	Unwedded; so 'twere better far for all,
6150	Than thus to live a scandal and reproach.
6150	Well may the long-hair'd Greeks triumphant boast,
6150	Who think thee, from thine outward show, a chief
6150	Among our warriors; but thou hast in truth
6150	Nor strength of mind, nor courage in the fight.
6150	How was't that such as thou could e'er induce
6150	A noble band, in ocean-going ships
6150	To cross the main, with men of other lands
6150	Mixing in amity, and bearing thence
6150	A woman, fair of face, by marriage ties
6150	Bound to a race of warriors; to thy sire,
6150	Thy state, thy people, cause of endless grief,
6150	Of triumph to thy foes, contempt to thee!
6150	Durst thou the warlike Menelaus meet,
6150	Thou to thy cost shouldst learn the might of him
6150	Whose bride thou didst not fear to bear away:
6150	Then shouldst thou find of small avail thy lyre,
6150	Or Venus' gifts of beauty and of grace,
6150	Or, trampled in the dust, thy flowing hair.
6150	But too forbearing are the men of Troy;
6150	Else for the ills that thou hast wrought the state,
6150	"Ere now thy body had in stone been cas'd."""
6150	""
6150	To whom the godlike Paris thus replied:
6150	"""Hector, I needs must own thy censure just,"
6150	Nor without cause; thy dauntless courage knows
6150	Nor pause nor weariness; but as an axe,
6150	That in a strong man's hand, who fashions out
6150	Some naval timber, with unbated edge
6150	Cleaves the firm wood, and aids the striker's force;
6150	Ev'n so unwearied is thy warlike soul.
6150	Yet blame not me for golden Venus' gifts:
6150	The gifts of Heav'n are not to be despis'd,
6150	Which Heav'n may give, but man could not command.
6150	But if thou wilt that I should dare the fight,
6150	Bid that the Trojans and the Grecians all
6150	Be seated on the ground; and in the midst
6150	The warlike Menelaus and myself
6150	Stand front to front, for Helen and the spoils
6150	Of war to combat; and whoe'er shall prove
6150	The better man in conflict, let him bear
6150	The woman and the spoils in triumph home;
6150	While ye, the rest, in peace and friendship sworn,
6150	Shall still possess the fertile plains of Troy;
6150	And to their native Argos they return,
6150	"For noble steeds and lovely women fam'd."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and Hector joy'd to hear his words:
6150	Forth in the midst he stepp'd, and with his spear
6150	Grasp'd by the middle, stay'd the Trojan ranks.
6150	At him the long-haired Grecians bent their bows,
6150	Prompt to assail with arrows and with stones;
6150	But loud the monarch Agamemnon's voice
6150	"Was heard; ""Hold, Argives, hold! ye sons of Greece,"
6150	Shoot not! for Hector of the glancing helm
6150	"Hath, as it seems, some message to impart."""
6150	""
6150	He said; they held their hands, and silent stood
6150	Expectant, till to both thus Hector spoke:
6150	"""Hear now, ye Trojans, and ye well-greav'd Greeks,"
6150	The words of Paris, cause of all this war.
6150	He asks through me that all the host of Troy
6150	And Grecian warriors shall upon the ground
6150	Lay down their glitt'ring arms; while in the midst
6150	The warlike Menelaus and himself
6150	Stand front to front, for Helen and the spoils
6150	Of war to combat; and whoe'er shall prove
6150	The better man in conflict, let him bear
6150	The woman and the spoils in triumph home,
6150	"While we, the rest, firm peace and friendship swear."""
6150	""
6150	Thus Hector spoke; the rest in silence heard;
6150	But Menelaus, bold in fight, replied:
6150	"""Hear now my answer; in this quarrel I"
6150	May claim the chiefest share; and now I hope
6150	Trojans and Greeks may see the final close
6150	Of all the labours ye so long have borne
6150	T' avenge my wrong, at Paris' hand sustain'd.
6150	And of us two whiche'er is doom'd to death,
6150	So let him die! the rest, depart in peace.
6150	Bring then two lambs, one white, the other black,
6150	For Tellus and for Sol; we on our part
6150	Will bring another, for Saturnian Jove:
6150	And let the majesty of Priam too
6150	Appear, himself to consecrate our oaths,
6150	(For reckless are his sons, and void of faith,)
6150	That none Jove's oath may dare to violate.
6150	For young men's spirits are too quickly stirr'd;
6150	But in the councils check'd by rev'rend age,
6150	Alike are weigh'd the future and the past,
6150	"And for all int'rests due provision made."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and Greeks and Trojans gladly heard,
6150	In hopes of respite from the weary war.
6150	They rang'd the cars in ranks; and they themselves
6150	Descending doff'd their arms, and laid them down
6150	Close each by each, with narrow space between.
6150	Two heralds to the city Hector sent
6150	To bring the lambs, and aged Priam call;
6150	While Agamemnon to the hollow ships,
6150	Their lamb to bring, in haste Talthybius sent:
6150	He heard, and straight the monarch's voice obey'd.
6150	""
6150	Meantime to white-arm'd Helen Iris sped,
6150	The heav'nly messenger: in form she seem'd
6150	Her husband's sister, whom Antenor's son,
6150	The valiant Helicaon had to wife,
6150	Laodice, of Priam's daughters all
6150	Loveliest of face: she in her chamber found
6150	Her whom she sought: a mighty web she wove,
6150	Of double woof and brilliant hues; whereon
6150	Was interwoven many a toilsome strife
6150	Of Trojan warriors and of brass-clad Greeks,
6150	For her encounter'd at the hand of Mars.
6150	Beside her Iris stood, and thus she spoke:
6150	"""Come, sister dear, and see the glorious deeds"
6150	Of Trojan warriors and of brass-clad Greeks.
6150	They who erewhile, impatient for the fight,
6150	Roll'd o'er the plain the woful tide of war,
6150	Now silent sit, the storm of battle hush'd,
6150	Reclining on their shields, their lances bright
6150	Beside them reared; while Paris in the midst
6150	And warlike Menelaus, stand prepar'd
6150	With the long spear for thee to fight; thyself
6150	"The prize of conquest and the victor's wife."""
6150	""
6150	Thus as she spoke, in Helen's breast arose
6150	Fond recollection of her former Lord,
6150	Her home, and parents; o'er her head she threw
6150	A snowy veil; and shedding tender tears
6150	She issu'd forth, not unaccompanied;
6150	For with her went fair AEthra, Pittheus' child,
6150	And stag-ey'd Clymene, her maidens twain.
6150	They quickly at the Scaean gate arriv'd.
6150	""
6150	Attending there on aged Priam, sat,
6150	The Elders of the city; Panthous,
6150	And Lampus, and Thymaetes; Clytius,
6150	Bold Icetaon, and Ucalegon,
6150	With sage Antenor, wise in council both:
6150	All these were gather'd at the Scaean gate;
6150	By age exempt from war, but in discourse
6150	Abundant, as the cricket, that on high
6150	From topmost boughs of forest tree sends forth
6150	His delicate music; so on Ilium's tow'rs
6150	Sat the sage chiefs and councillors of Troy.
6150	Helen they saw, as to the tow'r she came;
6150	"And ""'tis no marvel,"" one to other said,"
6150	"""The valiant Trojans and the well-greav'd Greeks"
6150	For beauty such as this should long endure
6150	The toils of war; for goddess-like she seems;
6150	And yet, despite her beauty, let her go,
6150	"Nor bring on us and on our sons a curse."""
6150	""
6150	Thus they; but aged Priam Helen call'd:
6150	"""Come here, my child, and sitting by my side,"
6150	From whence thou canst discern thy former Lord,
6150	His kindred, and thy friends (not thee I blame,
6150	But to the Gods I owe this woful war),
6150	Tell me the name of yonder mighty chief
6150	Among the Greeks a warrior brave and strong:
6150	Others in height surpass him; but my eyes
6150	A form so noble never yet beheld,
6150	"Nor so august; he moves, a King indeed!"""
6150	""
6150	To whom in answer, Helen, heav'nly fair:
6150	"""With rev'rence, dearest father, and with shame"
6150	I look on thee: oh would that I had died
6150	That day when hither with thy son I came,
6150	And left my husband, friends, and darling child,
6150	And all the lov'd companions of my youth:
6150	That I died not, with grief I pine away.
6150	But to thy question; I will tell thee true;
6150	Yon chief is Agamemnon, Atreus' son,
6150	Wide-reigning, mighty monarch, ruler good,
6150	And valiant warrior; in my husband's name,
6150	"Lost as I am, I call'd him brother once."""
6150	""
6150	She spoke: th' old man admiring gaz'd, and cried,
6150	"""Oh bless'd Atrides, child of happy fate,"
6150	Favour'd of Heav'n! how many noble Greeks
6150	Obey thy rule! In vine-clad Phrygia once
6150	I saw the hosts of Phrygian warriors wheel
6150	Their rapid steeds; and with them, all the bands
6150	Of Otreus, and of Mygdon, godlike King,
6150	Who lay encamp'd beside Sangarius' stream:
6150	I too with them was number'd, in the day
6150	When met them in the field the Amazons,
6150	The woman-warriors; but their forces all
6150	"Reach'd not the number of the keen-ey'd Greeks."""
6150	""
6150	Ulysses next the old man saw, and ask'd,
6150	"""Tell me again, dear child, who this may be,"
6150	In stature less than Atreus' royal son,
6150	But broader-shoulder'd, and of ampler chest.
6150	His arms are laid upon the fertile plain,
6150	But he himself is moving through the ranks,
6150	Inspecting, like a full-fleec'd ram, that moves
6150	"Majestic through a flock of snow-white ewes."""
6150	""
6150	To whom Jove's offspring, Helen, thus replied:
6150	"""The wise Ulysses that, Laertes' son:"
6150	Though bred in rugged Ithaca, yet vers'd
6150	"In ev'ry stratagem, and deep device."""
6150	"""O woman,"" then the sage Antenor said,"
6150	"""Of these thy words I can the truth avouch;"
6150	For hither when on thine account to treat,
6150	Brave Menelaus and Ulysses came,
6150	I lodg'd them in my house, and lov'd them both,
6150	And studied well the form and mind of each.
6150	As they with Trojans mix'd in social guise,
6150	When both were standing, o'er his comrade high
6150	With broad-set shoulders Menelaus stood;
6150	Seated, Ulysses was the nobler form:
6150	Then, in the great Assembly, when to all
6150	Their public speech and argument they fram'd,
6150	In fluent language Menelaus spoke,
6150	In words though few, yet clear; though young in years,
6150	No wordy babbler, wasteful of his speech:
6150	But when the skill'd Ulysses rose to speak,
6150	With down-cast visage would he stand, his eyes
6150	Bent on the ground; the staff he bore, nor back
6150	He wav'd, nor forward, but like one untaught,
6150	He held it motionless; who only saw
6150	Would say that he was mad, or void of sense;
6150	But when his chest its deep-ton'd voice sent forth,
6150	With words that fell like flakes of wintry snow,
6150	No mortal with Ulysses could compare:
6150	"Then little reck'd we of his outward show."""
6150	""
6150	At sight of Ajax next th' old man enquir'd;
6150	"""Who is yon other warrior, brave and strong,"
6150	"Tow'ring o'er all with head and shoulders broad?"""
6150	""
6150	To whom, in answer, Helen, heav'nly fair:
6150	"""Gigantic Ajax that, the prop of Greece;"
6150	And by his side Idomeneus of Crete
6150	Stands godlike, circled round by Cretan chiefs.
6150	The warlike Menelaus welcom'd him
6150	Oft in our palace, when from Crete he came.
6150	Now all the other keen-ey'd Greeks I see,
6150	Whom once I knew, and now could call by name;
6150	But two I miss, two captains of the host,
6150	My own two brethren, and my mother's sons,
6150	Castor and Pollux; Castor, charioteer
6150	Unrivalled, Pollux, matchless pugilist.
6150	In Lacedaemon have they stay'd behind?
6150	Or can it be, in ocean-going ships
6150	That they have come indeed, but shun to join
6150	The fight of warriors, fearful of the shame,
6150	"And deep disgrace that on my name attend?"""
6150	Thus she; but they beneath the teeming earth
6150	In Lacedaemon lay, their native land.
6150	""
6150	Meanwhile the heralds through the city bore
6150	The treaty off'rings to the Gods; the lambs,
6150	And genial wine, the produce of the soil,
6150	In goat-skin flasks: therewith a flagon bright,
6150	And cups of gold, Idaeus brought, and stood
6150	Beside the aged King, as thus he spoke:
6150	"""Son of Laomedon, arise! the chiefs"
6150	Of Trojan warriors and of brass-clad Greeks
6150	Call for thy presence on the battle-plain
6150	To swear a truce; where Paris in the midst
6150	And warlike Menelaus stand prepar'd
6150	With the long spear for Helen and the spoils
6150	Of war to combat, that whoe'er may prove
6150	The better man in fight, may bear away
6150	The woman and the spoils in triumph home;
6150	While we, the rest, in peace and friendship sworn,
6150	Shall still possess the fertile plains of Troy;
6150	And to their native Argos they return.
6150	"For noble steeds and lovely women fam'd."""
6150	""
6150	He said; the old man shuddered at his words:
6150	But to his comrades gave command forthwith.
6150	To yoke his car; and they his word obey'd.
6150	Priam, ascending, gather'd up the reins,
6150	And with Antenor by his side, the twain
6150	Drove through the Scaean gate their flying steeds.
6150	""
6150	But when between th' opposing ranks they came,
6150	Alighting from the car, they mov'd on foot
6150	Between the Trojan and the Grecian hosts.
6150	Uprose then Agamemnon, King of men,
6150	Uprose the sage Ulysses; to the front
6150	The heralds brought the off'rings to the Gods,
6150	And in the flagon mix'd the wine, and pour'd
6150	The hallowing water on the monarchs' hands.
6150	His dagger then the son of Atreus drew,
6150	Suspended, as was wont, beside the hilt
6150	Of his great sword; and from the victim's head
6150	He cut the sacred lock, which to the chiefs
6150	Of Troy and Greece the heralds portion'd out.
6150	Then with uplifted hands he pray'd aloud:
6150	"""O Father Jove! who rul'st from Ida's height,"
6150	Most great! most glorious! and thou Sun, who see'st
6150	And hearest all things! Rivers! and thou Earth!
6150	And ye, who after death beneath the earth
6150	Your vengeance wreak on souls of men forsworn,
6150	Be witness ye, and this our cov'nant guard.
6150	If Menelaus fall by Paris' hand,
6150	Let him retain both Helen and the spoil,
6150	While in our ships we take our homeward way;
6150	If Paris be by Menelaus slain,
6150	Troy shall surrender Helen and the spoil,
6150	With compensation due to Greece, that so
6150	A record may to future days remain.
6150	But, Paris slain, if Priam and his sons
6150	The promis'd compensation shall withhold,
6150	Then here, my rights in battle to assert,
6150	"Will I remain, till I the end achieve."""
6150	""
6150	Thus as he spoke, across the victims' throats
6150	He drew the pitiless blade, and on the ground
6150	He laid them gasping, as the stream of life
6150	Pour'd forth, their vigour by the blade subdued.
6150	Then, from the flagon drawn, from out the cups
6150	The wine they pour'd; and to th' eternal Gods
6150	They pray'd; and thus from Trojans and from Greeks
6150	"Arose the joint petition; ""Grant, O Jove!"
6150	Most great! most glorious! grant, ye heav'nly pow'rs,
6150	That whosoe'er this solemn truce shall break,
6150	Ev'n as this wine we pour, their hearts' best blood,
6150	Theirs and their children's, on the earth be pour'd,
6150	"And strangers in subjection take their wives!"""
6150	""
6150	Thus they; but Jove, unyielding, heard their pray'r.
6150	The rites perform'd, then aged Priam spoke:
6150	"""Hear me, ye Trojans, and ye well-greav'd Greeks!"
6150	To Ilium's breezy heights I now withdraw,
6150	For that mine eyes will not endure the sight
6150	Of warlike Menelaus and my son
6150	Engag'd in deadly combat; of the two
6150	Which may be doom'd to death, is only known
6150	"To Jove, and to th' immortal pow'rs of Heav'n."""
6150	""
6150	Thus spoke the godlike King; and on the car
6150	He plac'd the consecrated lambs; himself
6150	Ascending then, he gather'd up the reins,
6150	And with Antenor by his side, the twain
6150	To Ilium's walls retrac'd their homeward way.
6150	""
6150	Then Hector, son of Priam, measur'd out,
6150	With sage Ulysses join'd, th' allotted space;
6150	Next, in the brass-bound helmet cast the lots,
6150	Which of the two the first should throw the spear.
6150	The crowd, with hands uplifted, to the Gods,
6150	Trojans and Greeks alike, address'd their pray'r:
6150	"""O Father Jove! who rul'st from Ida's height,"
6150	Most great! most glorious! grant that whosoe'er
6150	On both our armies hath this turmoil brought
6150	May undergo the doom of death, and we,
6150	"The rest, firm peace and lasting friendship swear."""
6150	""
6150	Thus they; great Hector of the glancing helm,
6150	With eyes averted, shook the casque; and forth
6150	Was cast the lot of Paris; on the ground
6150	The rest lay down by ranks, where near to each
6150	Were rang'd his active steeds, and glitt'ring arms.
6150	Then o'er his shoulders fair-hair'd Helen's Lord,
6150	The godlike Paris, donn'd his armour bright:
6150	First on his legs the well-wrought greaves he fix'd,
6150	Fasten'd with silver clasps; his ample chest
6150	A breastplate guarded, by Lycaon lent,
6150	His brother, but which fitted well his form.
6150	Around his shoulders slung, his sword he bore,
6150	Brass-bladed, silver-studded; then his shield
6150	Weighty and strong; and on his firm-set head
6150	A helm he wore, well wrought, with horsehair plume
6150	That nodded, fearful, o'er his brow; his hand
6150	Grasp'd the firm spear, familiar to his hold.
6150	Prepar'd alike the adverse warrior stood.
6150	""
6150	They, from the crowd apart their armour donn'd,
6150	Came forth: and each, with eyes of mutual hate,
6150	Regarded each: admiring wonder seiz'd
6150	The Trojan warriors and the well-greav'd Greeks,
6150	As in the centre of the measur'd ground
6150	They stood oppos'd, and pois'd their quiv'ring spears.
6150	First Paris threw his weighty spear, and struck
6150	Fair in the midst Atrides' buckler round,
6150	But broke not through; upon the stubborn targe
6150	Was bent the lance's point; then thus to Jove,
6150	His weapon hurling, Menelaus pray'd:
6150	"""Great King, on him who wrought me causeless wrong,"
6150	On Paris, grant that retribution due
6150	My arm may bring; that men in days to come
6150	May fear their host to injure, and repay
6150	"With treach'rous wile his hospitable cares."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and poising, hurl'd his weighty spear:
6150	Full in the midst it struck the buckler round;
6150	Right through the buckler pass'd the sturdy spear,
6150	And through the gorgeous breastplate, and within
6150	Cut through the linen vest; but Paris, back
6150	Inclining, stoop'd, and shunn'd the doom of death.
6150	""
6150	Atrides then his silver-studded sword
6150	Rearing on high, a mighty blow let fall
6150	On Paris' helm; but shiv'ring in his hand
6150	In countless fragments new the faithless blade.
6150	Then thus to Jove, with eyes uplift to Heav'n,
6150	"Atrides made his moan: ""O Father Jove!"
6150	Of all the Gods, the most unfriendly thou!
6150	On Paris' head I hop'd for all his crimes
6150	To wreak my vengeance due; but in my grasp
6150	My faithless sword is shatter'd, and my spear
6150	"Hath bootless left my hand, nor reached my foe."""
6150	Then onward rushing, by the horsehair plume
6150	He seiz'd his foeman's helm, and wrenching round
6150	Dragg'd by main force amid the well-greav'd Greeks.
6150	The broider'd strap, that, pass'd beneath his beard,
6150	The helmet held, the warrior's throat compress'd:
6150	Then had Atrides dragg'd him from the field,
6150	And endless fame acquir'd; but Venus, child
6150	Of Jove, her fav'rite's peril quickly saw.
6150	And broke the throttling strap of tough bull's hide.
6150	In the broad hand the empty helm remained.
6150	The trophy, by their champion whirl'd amid
6150	The well-greav'd Greeks, his eager comrades seiz'd;
6150	While he, infuriate, rush'd with murd'rous aim
6150	On Priam's son; but him, the Queen of Love
6150	(As Gods can only) from the field convey'd,
6150	Wrapt in a misty cloud; and on a couch,
6150	Sweet perfumes breathing, gently laid him down;
6150	Then went in search of Helen; her she found,
6150	Circled with Trojan dames, on Ilium's tow'r:
6150	Her by her airy robe the Goddess held,
6150	And in the likeness of an aged dame
6150	Who oft for her, in Sparta when she dwelt,
6150	Many a fair fleece had wrought, and lov'd her well,
6150	"Address'd her thus: ""Come, Helen, to thy house;"
6150	Come, Paris calls thee; in his chamber he
6150	Expects thee, resting on luxurious couch,
6150	In costly garb, with manly beauty grac'd:
6150	Not from the fight of warriors wouldst thou deem
6150	He late had come, but for the dance prepar'd,
6150	"Or resting from the dance's pleasing toil."""
6150	""
6150	She said, and Helen's spirit within her mov'd;
6150	And when she saw the Goddess' beauteous neck,
6150	Her lovely bosom, and her glowing eyes,
6150	She gaz'd in wonder, and address'd her thus:
6150	"""Oh why, great Goddess, make me thus thy sport?"
6150	Seek'st thou to bear me far away from hence
6150	To some fair Phrygian or Maeonian town,
6150	If there some mortal have thy favour gain'd?
6150	Or, for that Menelaus in the field
6150	Hath vanquish'd Paris, and is willing yet
6150	That I, his bane, should to his home return;
6150	Here art thou found, to weave again thy wiles!
6150	Go then thyself! thy godship abdicate!
6150	Renounce Olympus! lavish here on him
6150	Thy pity and thy care! he may perchance
6150	Make thee his wife--at least his paramour!
6150	But thither go not I! foul shame it were
6150	Again to share his bed; the dames of Troy
6150	Will for a byword hold me; and e'en now
6150	"My soul with endless sorrow is possess'd."""
6150	""
6150	To whom in anger heav'nly Venus spoke:
6150	"""Incense me not, poor fool! lest I in wrath"
6150	Desert thee quite, and as I heretofore
6150	Have lov'd, so make thee object of my hate;
6150	And kindle, 'twixt the Trojans and the Greeks,
6150	"Such bitter feuds, as both shall wreak on thee."""
6150	""
6150	She said; and trembled Helen, child of Jove;
6150	She rose in silence; in a snow-white veil
6150	All glitt'ring, shrouded; by the Goddess led
6150	She pass'd, unnotic'd by the Trojan dames.
6150	But when to Paris' splendid house they came,
6150	Thronging around her, her attendants gave
6150	Their duteous service; through the lofty hall
6150	With queenly grace the godlike woman pass'd.
6150	A seat the laughter-loving Goddess plac'd
6150	By Paris' side; there Helen sat, the child
6150	Of aegis-bearing Jove, with downcast eyes,
6150	Yet with sharp words she thus address'd her Lord:
6150	"""Back from the battle? would thou there hadst died"
6150	Beneath a warrior's arm, whom once I call'd
6150	My husband! vainly didst thou boast erewhile
6150	Thine arm, thy dauntless courage, and thy spear
6150	The warlike Menelaus should subdue!
6150	Go now again, and challenge to the fight
6150	The warlike Menelaus. Be thou ware!
6150	I warn thee, pause, ere madly thou presume
6150	With fair-hair'd Menelaus to contend!
6150	"Soon shouldst thou fall beneath his conqu'ring spear."""
6150	""
6150	"To whom thus Paris: ""Wring not thus my soul"
6150	With keen reproaches: now, with Pallas' aid,
6150	Hath Menelaus conquer'd; but my day
6150	Will come: I too can boast my guardian Gods.
6150	But turn we now to love, and love's delights;
6150	For never did thy beauty so inflame
6150	My sense; not when from Lacedaemon first
6150	I bore thee in my ocean-going ships,
6150	And revell'd in thy love on Cranae's isle,
6150	"As now it fills my soul with fond desire."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and led her to the nuptial couch;
6150	Her Lord she follow'd; and while there reclin'd
6150	Upon the richly-inlaid couch they lay,
6150	Atrides, like a lion baffled, rush'd
6150	Amid the crowd, if haply he might find
6150	The godlike Paris; but not one of all
6150	The Trojans and their brave allies could aid
6150	The warlike Menelaus in his search;
6150	Not that, for love, would any one that knew
6150	Have screen'd him from his anger, for they all
6150	Abhorr'd him as the shade of death: then thus
6150	Outspoke great Agamemnon, King of men:
6150	"""Hear me, ye Trojans, Dardans, and Allies!"
6150	With warlike Menelaus rests, 'tis plain,
6150	The prize of vict'ry: then surrender ye
6150	The Argive Helen and the spoils of war,
6150	With compensation due to Greece, that so
6150	"A record may to future days remain."""
6150	""
6150	Thus he; the Greeks, assenting, cheer'd his words.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	ARGUMENT.
6150	""
6150	THE BREACH OF THE TRUCE, AND THE FIRST BATTLE.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	The Gods deliberate in council concerning the Trojan war: they agree
6150	upon the continuation of it, and Jupiter sends down Minerva to break
6150	the truce. She persuades Pandarus to aim an arrow at Menelaus, who is
6150	wounded, but cured by Machaon. In the mean time some of the Trojan
6150	troops attack the Greeks. Agamemnon is distinguished in all the parts
6150	of a good general; he reviews the troops, and exhorts the leaders, some
6150	by praises, and others by reproofs. Nestor is particularly celebrated
6150	for his military discipline. The battle joins, and great numbers are
6150	slain on both sides.
6150	""
6150	The same day continues through this, as through the last book; as it
6150	does also through the two following, and almost to the end of the
6150	seventh book. The scene is wholly in the field before Troy.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	BOOK IV.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	On golden pavement, round the board of Jove,
6150	The Gods were gather'd; Hebe in the midst
6150	Pour'd the sweet nectar; they, in golden cups,
6150	Each other pledg'd, as down they look'd on Troy.
6150	Then Jove, with cutting words and taunting tone,
6150	Began the wrath of Juno to provoke:
6150	"""Two Goddesses for Menelaus fight,"
6150	Thou, Juno, Queen of Argos, and with thee
6150	Minerva, shield of warriors; but ye two
6150	Sitting aloof, well-pleased it seems, look on;
6150	While laughter-loving Venus, at the side
6150	Of Paris standing, still averts his fate,
6150	And rescues, when, as now, expecting death.
6150	To warlike Menelaus we decree,
6150	Of right, the vict'ry; but consult we now
6150	What may the issue be; if we shall light
6150	Again the name of war and discord fierce,
6150	Or the two sides in peace and friendship join.
6150	For me, if thus your gen'ral voice incline,
6150	Let Priam's city stand, and Helen back
6150	"To warlike Menelaus be restor'd."""
6150	""
6150	So spoke the God; but seated side by side,
6150	Juno and Pallas glances interchang'd
6150	Of ill portent for Troy; Pallas indeed
6150	Sat silent; and, though inly wroth with Jove,
6150	Yet answer'd not a word; but Juno's breast
6150	Could not contain her rage, and thus she spoke:
6150	"""What words, dread son of Saturn, dost thou speak?"
6150	How wouldst thou render vain, and void of fruit,
6150	My weary labour and my horses' toil,
6150	To stir the people, and on Priam's self,
6150	And Priam's offspring, bring disastrous fate?
6150	"Do as thou wilt! yet not with our consent."""
6150	""
6150	To whom, in wrath, the Cloud-compeller thus:
6150	"""Revengeful! how have Priam and his sons"
6150	So deeply injur'd thee, that thus thou seek'st
6150	With unabated anger to pursue,
6150	Till thou o'erthrow, the strong-built walls of Troy?
6150	Couldst thou but force the gates, and entering in
6150	On Priam's mangled flesh, and Priam's sons,
6150	And Trojans all, a bloody banquet make.
6150	Perchance thy fury might at length be stayed.
6150	But have thy will, lest this in future times
6150	'Twixt me and thee be cause of strife renew'd.
6150	Yet hear my words, and ponder what I say:
6150	If e'er, in times to come, my will should be
6150	Some city to destroy, inhabited
6150	By men beloved of thee, seek not to turn
6150	My wrath aside, but yield, as I do now,
6150	Consenting, but with heart that ill consents;
6150	For of all cities fair, beneath the sun
6150	And starry Heaven, the abode of mortal men,
6150	None to my soul was dear as sacred Troy,
6150	And Priam's self, and Priam's warrior race.
6150	For with drink-off'rings due, and fat of lambs,
6150	My altar still hath at their hands been fed;
6150	"Such honour hath to us been ever paid."""
6150	""
6150	To whom the stag-ey'd Juno thus replied:
6150	"""Three cities are there, dearest to my heart;"
6150	Argos, and Sparta, and the ample streets
6150	Of rich Mycenae; work on them thy will;
6150	Destroy them, if thine anger they incur;
6150	I will not interpose, nor hinder thee;
6150	Mourn them I shall; reluctant see their fall,
6150	But not resist; for sovereign is thy will.
6150	Yet should my labours not be fruitless all;
6150	For I too am a God; my blood is thine;
6150	Worthy of honour, as the eldest born
6150	Of deep-designing Saturn, and thy wife;
6150	Thine, who o'er all th' Immortals reign'st supreme.
6150	But yield we each to other, I to thee,
6150	And thou to me; the other Gods will all
6150	By us be rul'd. On Pallas then enjoin
6150	That to the battle-field of Greece and Troy
6150	She haste, and so contrive that Trojans first
6150	"May break the treaty, and the Greeks assail."""
6150	""
6150	She said: the Sire of Gods and men complied,
6150	And thus with winged words to Pallas spoke:
6150	"""Go to the battle-field of Greece and Troy"
6150	In haste, and so contrive that Trojans first
6150	"May break the treaty, and the Greeks assail."""
6150	""
6150	His words fresh impulse gave to Pallas' zeal,
6150	And from Olympus' heights in haste she sped;
6150	Like to a meteor, that, of grave portent
6150	To warring armies or sea-faring men,
6150	The son of deep-designing Saturn sends,
6150	Bright-flashing, scatt'ring fiery sparks around,
6150	The blue-ey'd Goddess darted down to earth,
6150	And lighted in the midst; amazement held
6150	The Trojan warriors and the well-greav'd Greeks;
6150	"And one to other look'd and said, ""What means"
6150	This sign? Must fearful battle rage again,
6150	Or may we hope for gentle peace from Jove,
6150	"Who to mankind dispenses peace and war?"""
6150	Such was the converse Greeks and Trojans held.
6150	Pallas meanwhile, amid the Trojan host,
6150	Clad in the likeness of Antenor's son,
6150	Laodocus, a spearman stout and brave,
6150	Search'd here and there, if haply she might find
6150	The godlike Pandarus; Lycaon's son
6150	She found, of noble birth and stalwart form,
6150	Standing, encircled by his sturdy band
6150	Of bucklered followers from AEsepus' stream,
6150	She stood beside him, and address'd him thus:
6150	""
6150	"""Wilt thou by me be ruled, Lycaon's son?"
6150	For durst thou but at Menelaus shoot
6150	Thy winged arrow, great would be thy fame,
6150	And great thy favour with the men of Troy,
6150	And most of all with Paris; at his hand
6150	Thou shalt receive rich guerdon, when he hears
6150	That warlike Menelaus, by thy shaft
6150	Subdued, is laid upon the fun'ral pyre.
6150	Bend then thy bow at Atreus' glorious son,
6150	Vowing to Phoebus, Lycia's guardian God,
6150	The Archer-King, to pay of firstling lambs
6150	An ample hecatomb, when home return'd
6150	"In safety to Zeleia's sacred town."""
6150	Thus she; and, fool, he listen'd to her words.
6150	Straight he uncas'd his polish'd bow, his spoil
6150	Won from a mountain ibex, which himself,
6150	In ambush lurking, through the breast had shot,
6150	True to his aim, as from behind a crag
6150	He came in sight; prone on the rock he fell;
6150	With horns of sixteen palms his head was crown'd;
6150	These deftly wrought a skilful workman's hand,
6150	And polish'd smooth, and tipp'd the ends with gold.
6150	He bent, and resting on the ground his bow,
6150	Strung it anew; his faithful comrades held
6150	Their shields before him, lest the sons of Greece
6150	Should make their onset ere his shaft could reach
6150	The warlike Menelaus, Atreus' son.
6150	His quiver then withdrawing from its case,
6150	With care a shaft he chose, ne'er shot before,
6150	Well-feather'd, messenger of pangs and death;
6150	The stinging arrow fitted to the string,
6150	And vow'd to Phoebus, Lycia's guardian God,
6150	The Archer-King, to pay of firstling lambs
6150	An ample hecatomb, when home return'd
6150	In safety to Zeleia's sacred town.
6150	At once the sinew and the notch he drew;
6150	The sinew to his breast, and to the bow
6150	The iron head; then, when the mighty bow
6150	Was to a circle strain'd, sharp rang the horn,
6150	And loud the sinew twang'd, as tow'rd the crowd
6150	With deadly speed the eager arrow sprang.
6150	""
6150	Nor, Menelaus, was thy safety then
6150	Uncar'd for of the Gods; Jove's daughter first,
6150	Pallas, before thee stood, and turn'd aside
6150	The pointed arrow; turn'd it so aside
6150	As when a mother from her infant's cheek,
6150	Wrapt in sweet slumbers, brushes off a fly;
6150	Its course she so directed that it struck
6150	Just where the golden clasps the belt restrain'd,
6150	And where the breastplate, doubled, check'd its force.
6150	On the close-fitting belt the arrow struck;
6150	Right through the belt of curious workmanship
6150	It drove, and through the breastplate richly wrought,
6150	And through the coat of mail he wore beneath,
6150	His inmost guard and best defence to check
6150	The hostile weapons' force; yet onward still
6150	The arrow drove, and graz'd the hero's flesh.
6150	Forth issued from the wound the crimson blood.
6150	As when some Carian or Maeonian maid,
6150	With crimson dye the ivory stains, designed
6150	To be the cheek-piece of a warrior's steed,
6150	By many a valiant horseman coveted,
6150	As in the house it lies, a monarch's boast,
6150	The horse adorning, and the horseman's pride:
6150	So, Menelaus, then thy graceful thighs,
6150	And knees, and ancles, with thy blood were dy'd.
6150	""
6150	Great Agamemnon shudder'd as he saw
6150	The crimson drops out-welling from the wound;
6150	Shudder'd the warlike Menelaus' self;
6150	But when not buried in his flesh he saw
6150	The barb and sinew, back his spirit came.
6150	""
6150	Then deeply groaning, Agamemnon spoke,
6150	As Menelaus by the hand he held,
6150	"And with him groan'd his comrades: ""Brother dear,"
6150	I wrought thy death when late, on compact sworn,
6150	I sent thee forth alone for Greece to fight;
6150	Wounded by Trojans, who their plighted faith
6150	Have trodden under foot; but not in vain
6150	Are solemn cov'nants and the blood of lambs,
6150	The treaty wine outpoured, and hand-plight given,
6150	Wherein men place their trust; if not at once,
6150	Yet soon or late will Jove assert their claim;
6150	And heavy penalties the perjured pay
6150	With their own blood, their children's, and their wives'.
6150	So in my inmost soul full well I know
6150	The day shall come when this imperial Troy,
6150	And Priam's race, and Priam's royal self,
6150	Shall in one common ruin be o'erthrown;
6150	And Saturn's son himself, high-throned Jove,
6150	Who dwells in Heav'n, shall in their faces flash
6150	His aegis dark and dread, this treach'rous deed
6150	Avenging; this shall surely come to pass.
6150	But, Menelaus, deep will be my grief,
6150	If thou shouldst perish, meeting thus thy fate.
6150	To thirsty Argos should I then return
6150	By foul disgrace o'erwhelm'd; for, with thy fall,
6150	The Greeks will mind them of their native land;
6150	And as a trophy to the sons of Troy
6150	The Argive Helen leave; thy bones meanwhile
6150	Shall moulder here beneath a foreign soil.
6150	Thy work undone; and with insulting scorn
6150	Some vaunting Trojan, leaping on the tomb
6150	Of noble Menelaus, thus shall say:
6150	'On all his foes may Agamemnon so
6150	His wrath accomplish, who hath hither led
6150	Of Greeks a mighty army, all in vain;
6150	And bootless home with empty ships hath gone,
6150	And valiant Menelaus left behind;'
6150	"Thus when men speak, gape, earth, and hide my shame."""
6150	""
6150	To whom the fair-hair'd Menelaus thus
6150	"With, cheering words: ""Fear not thyself, nor cause"
6150	The troops to fear: the arrow hath not touch'd
6150	A vital part: the sparkling belt hath first
6150	Turn'd it aside, the doublet next beneath,
6150	"And coat of mail, the work of arm'rer's hands."""
6150	""
6150	To whom the monarch Agamemnon thus:
6150	"""Dear Menelaus, may thy words be true!"
6150	The leech shall tend thy wound, and spread it o'er
6150	"With healing ointments to assuage the pain."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and to the sacred herald call'd:
6150	"""Haste thee, Talthybius! summon with all speed"
6150	The son of AEsculapius, peerless leech,
6150	Machaon; bid him hither haste to see
6150	The warlike Menelaus, chief of Greeks,
6150	Who by an arrow from some practis'd hand,
6150	Trojan or Lycian, hath receiv'd a wound;
6150	"A cause of boast to them, to us of grief."""
6150	""
6150	He said, nor did the herald not obey,
6150	But through the brass-clad ranks of Greece he pass'd,
6150	In search of brave Machaon; him he found
6150	Standing, by buckler'd warriors bold begirt,
6150	Who follow'd him from Trica's grassy plains.
6150	He stood beside him, and address'd him thus:
6150	"""Up, son of AEsculapius! Atreus' son,"
6150	The mighty monarch, summons thee to see
6150	The warlike Menelaus, chief of Greeks,
6150	Who by an arrow from some practis'd hand,
6150	Trojan or Lycian, hath receiv'd a wound;
6150	"A cause of boast to them, to us of grief."""
6150	""
6150	Thus he; and not unmov'd Machaon heard:
6150	They thro' the crowd, and thro' the wide-spread host,
6150	Together took their way; but when they came
6150	Where fair-hair'd Menelaus, wounded, stood,
6150	Around him in a ring the best of Greece,
6150	And in the midst the godlike chief himself,
6150	From the close-fitting belt the shaft he drew,
6150	Breaking the pointed barbs; the sparkling belt
6150	He loosen'd, and the doublet underneath,
6150	And coat of mail, the work of arm'rer's hand.
6150	But when the wound appear'd in sight, where struck
6150	The stinging arrow, from the clotted blood
6150	He cleans'd it, and applied with skilful hand
6150	The herbs of healing power, which Chiron erst
6150	In friendly guise upon his sire bestowed.
6150	""
6150	While round the valiant Menelaus they
6150	Were thus engag'd, advanc'd the Trojan hosts:
6150	They donn'd their arms, and for the fight prepar'd.
6150	In Agamemnon then no trace was seen
6150	Of laggard sloth, no shrinking from the fight,
6150	But full of ardour to the field he rush'd.
6150	He left his horses and brass-mounted car
6150	(The champing horses by Eurymedon,
6150	The son of Ptolemy, Peiraeus' son,
6150	Were held aloof), but with repeated charge
6150	Still to be near at hand, when faint with toil
6150	His limbs should fail him marshalling his host.
6150	Himself on foot the warrior ranks array'd;
6150	With cheering words addressing whom he found
6150	With zeal preparing for the battle-field:
6150	"""Relax not, valiant friends, your warlike toil;"
6150	For Jove to falsehood ne'er will give his aid;
6150	And they who first, regardless of their oaths,
6150	Have broken truce, shall with their flesh themselves
6150	The vultures feed, while we, their city raz'd,
6150	"Their wives and helpless children bear away."""
6150	""
6150	But whom remiss and shrinking from the war
6150	He found, with keen rebuke lie thus assail'd;
6150	"""Ye wretched Greeks, your country's foul reproach,"
6150	Have ye no sense of shame? Why stand ye thus
6150	Like timid fawns, that in the chase run down,
6150	Stand all bewildered, spiritless and tame?
6150	So stand ye now, nor dare to face the fight.
6150	What! will ye wait the Trojans' near approach,
6150	Where on the beach, beside the hoary deep,
6150	Our goodly ships are drawn, and see if Jove
6150	"Will o'er you his protecting hand extend?"""
6150	""
6150	As thus the King the serried ranks review'd,
6150	He came where thronging round their skilful chief
6150	Idomeneus, the warlike bands of Crete
6150	Were arming for the fight; Idomeneus,
6150	Of courage stubborn as the forest boar,
6150	The foremost ranks array'd; Meriones
6150	The rearmost squadrons had in charge; with joy
6150	The monarch Agamemnon saw, and thus
6150	With accents bland Idomeneus address'd:
6150	""
6150	"""Idomeneus, above all other Greeks,"
6150	In battle and elsewhere, I honour thee;
6150	And in the banquet, where the noblest mix
6150	The ruddy wine for chiefs alone reserved,
6150	Though others drink their share, yet by thy side
6150	Thy cup, like mine, still new replenished stands
6150	To drink at pleasure. Up then to the fight,
6150	"And show thyself the warrior that thou art."""
6150	""
6150	To whom the Cretan King, Idomeneus:
6150	"""In me, Atrides, thou shalt ever find,"
6150	As at the first I promis'd, comrade true;
6150	But go, and stir the other long-haired Greeks
6150	To speedy battle; since the Trojans now
6150	The truce have broken; and defeat and death
6150	"Must wait on those who have their oaths forsworn."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and Agamemnon went his way
6150	Rejoicing; through the crowd he pass'd, and came
6150	Where stood th' Ajaces; them, in act to arm,
6150	Amid a cloud of infantry he found;
6150	And as a goat-herd from his watch-tow'r crag
6150	Beholds a cloud advancing o'er the sea,
6150	By Zephyr's breath impell'd; as from afar
6150	He gazes, black as pitch, it sweeps along
6150	O'er the dark ocean's face, and with it brings
6150	A hurricane of rain; he, shudd'ring, sees,
6150	And drives his flock beneath the shelt'ring cave:
6150	So thick and dark, about th' Ajaces stirr'd,
6150	Impatient for the war, the stalwart youths,
6150	Black masses, bristling close with spear and shield.
6150	""
6150	Well pleas'd, the monarch Agamemnon saw,
6150	"And thus address'd them: ""Valiant chiefs, to you,"
6150	The leaders of the brass-clad Greeks, I give
6150	('Twere needless and unseemly) no commands;
6150	For well ye understand your troops to rouse
6150	To deeds of dauntless courage; would to Jove,
6150	To Pallas and Apollo, that such mind
6150	As is in you, in all the camp were found;
6150	Then soon should Priam's lofty city fall,
6150	"Tak'n and destroy'd by our victorious hands."""
6150	""
6150	Thus saying, them he left, and onward mov'd.
6150	Nestor, the smooth-tongu'd Pylian chief, he found
6150	The troops arraying, and to valiant deeds
6150	His friends encouraging; stout Pelagon,
6150	Alastor, Chromius, Haemon, warlike Prince,
6150	And Bias bold, his people's sure defence.
6150	In the front rank, with chariot and with horse,
6150	He plac'd the car-borne warriors; in the rear,
6150	Num'rous and brave, a cloud of infantry,
6150	Compactly mass'd, to stem the tide of war,
6150	Between the two he plac'd th' inferior troops,
6150	That e'en against their will they needs must fight.
6150	The horsemen first he charg'd, and bade them keep
6150	Their horses well in hand, nor wildly rush
6150	"Amid the tumult: ""See,"" he said, ""that none,"
6150	In skill or valour over-confident,
6150	Advance before his comrades, nor alone
6150	Retire; for so your lines were easier forc'd;
6150	But ranging each beside a hostile car,
6150	Thrust with your spears; for such the better way;
6150	By men so disciplin'd, in elder days
6150	"Were lofty walls and fenced towns destroy'd."""
6150	""
6150	Thus he, experienc'd in the wars of old;
6150	Well pleas'd, the monarch Agamemnon saw,
6150	"And thus address'd him; ""Would to Heav'n, old man,"
6150	That, as thy spirit, such too were thy strength
6150	And vigour of thy limbs; but now old age,
6150	The common lot of mortals, weighs thee down;
6150	Would I could see some others in thy place,
6150	"And thou couldst still be numbered with the young!"""
6150	""
6150	To whom Gerenian Nestor thus replied:
6150	"""Atrides, I too fain would see restor'd"
6150	The strength I once possess'd, what time I slew
6150	The godlike Ereuthalion; but the Gods
6150	On man bestow not all their gifts at once;
6150	I then was young, and now am bow'd with age,
6150	Yet with the chariots can I still go forth,
6150	And aid with sage advice: for such the right
6150	And privilege of age; to hurl the spear
6150	Belongs to younger men, who after me
6150	"Were born, who boast their vigour unimpair'd."""
6150	""
6150	He said; and Agamemnon went his way,
6150	Rejoicing: to Menestheus next he came,
6150	The son of Peteus, charioteer renown'd;
6150	Him found he, circled by th' Athenian bands,
6150	The raisers of the war-cry; close beside
6150	The sage Ulysses stood, around him rang'd,
6150	Not unrenown'd, the Cephalonian troops:
6150	The sound of battle had not reach'd their ears;
6150	For but of late the Greek and Trojan hosts
6150	Were set in motion; they expecting stood,
6150	Till other Grecian columns should advance,
6150	Assail the Trojans, and renew the war.
6150	""
6150	Atrides saw, and thus, reproachful, spoke:
6150	"""O son of Peteus, Heav'n-descended King!"
6150	And thou too, master of all tricky arts,
6150	Why, ling'ring, stand ye thus aloof, and wait
6150	For others coming? ye should be the first
6150	The hot assault of battle to confront;
6150	For ye are first my summons to receive,
6150	Whene'er the honour'd banquet we prepare:
6150	And well ye like to eat the sav'ry meat,
6150	And, at your will, the luscious wine-cups drain:
6150	Now stand ye here, and unconcern'd would see
6150	"Ten columns pass before you to the fight."""
6150	""
6150	To whom, with stern regard, Ulysses thus:
6150	"""What words have pass'd the barrier of thy lips,"
6150	Atrides? how with want of warlike zeal
6150	Canst thou reproach us? when the Greeks again
6150	The furious war shall waken, thou shalt see
6150	(If that thou care to see) amid the ranks
6150	Of Troy, the father of Telemachus
6150	"In the fore-front: thy words are empty wind."""
6150	""
6150	Atrides saw him chafed, and smiling, thus
6150	"Recalled his former words: ""Ulysses sage,"
6150	Laertes' high-born son, not over-much
6150	I give thee blame, or orders; for I know
6150	Thy mind to gentle counsels is inclin'd;
6150	Thy thoughts are one with mine; then come, henceforth
6150	Shall all be well; and if a hasty word
6150	"Have pass'd, may Heaven regard it as unsaid."""
6150	""
6150	Thus saying, them he left, and onward mov'd.
6150	The son of Tydeus, valiant Diomed,
6150	Standing he found amid his warlike steeds
6150	And well-built cars; beside him, Sthenelus,
6150	The son of Capaneus; Atrides saw,
6150	And thus address'd him with reproachful words:
6150	"""Alas! thou son of Tydeus, wise and bold,"
6150	Why crouch with fear? why thus appall'd survey
6150	The pass of war? not so had Tydeus crouch'd;
6150	His hand was ever ready from their foes
6150	To guard his comrades; so, at least, they say
6150	Whose eyes beheld his labours; I myself
6150	Nor met him e'er, nor saw; but, by report,
6150	Thy father was the foremost man of men.
6150	A stranger to Mycenae once he came,
6150	With godlike Polynices; not at war,
6150	But seeking succour for the troops that lay
6150	Encamp'd before the sacred walls of Thebes;
6150	For reinforcements earnestly they sued;
6150	The boon they ask'd was granted them, but Jove
6150	With unpropitious omens turn'd them back.
6150	Advancing on their journey, when they reach'd
6150	Asopus' grassy banks and rushes deep,
6150	The Greeks upon a mission Tydeus sent:
6150	He went; and many Thebans there he found
6150	Feasting in Eteocles' royal hall:
6150	Amid them all, a stranger and alone,
6150	He stood unterrified, and challeng'd all
6150	To wrestle with him, and with ease o'erthrew:
6150	So mighty was the aid that Pallas gave.
6150	Whereat indignant, they, on his return,
6150	An ambush set, of fifty chosen youths;
6150	Two were their leaders; Haemon's godlike son,
6150	Maeon, and Lycophontes, warrior brave,
6150	Son of Autophonus; and these too far'd
6150	But ill at Tydeus' hand; he slew them all:
6150	Maeon alone, obedient to the Gods,
6150	He spar'd, and bade him bear the tidings home.
6150	Such Tydeus was: though greater in debate,
6150	"His son will never rival him in arms."""
6150	""
6150	He said: brave Diomed in silence heard,
6150	Submissive to the monarch's stern rebuke;
6150	Then answer'd thus the son of Capaneus:
6150	"""Atrides, speak not falsely: well thou know'st"
6150	The truth, that we our fathers far surpass.
6150	The seven-gated city, Thebes, we took,
6150	With smaller force beneath the wall of Mars,
6150	Trusting to heav'nly signs, and fav'ring Jove,
6150	Where they by blind, presumptuous folly fail'd;
6150	"Then equal not our fathers' deeds with ours."""
6150	""
6150	To whom thus Diomed, with stern regard:
6150	"""Father, be silent; hearken to my words:"
6150	I blame not Agamemnon, King of men,
6150	Who thus to battle stirs the well-greav'd Greeks:
6150	His will the glory be if we o'ercome
6150	The valiant Trojans, and their city take;
6150	Great too his loss if they o'er us prevail:
6150	"Then come, let us too for the fight prepare."""
6150	""
6150	He said; and from the car leap'd down in arms:
6150	Fierce rang the armour on the warrior's breast,
6150	That ev'n the stoutest heart might quail with fear.
6150	""
6150	As by the west wind driv'n, the ocean waves
6150	Dash forward on the far-resounding shore,
6150	Wave upon wave; first curls the ruffled sea
6150	With whit'ning crests; anon with thund'ring roar
6150	It breaks upon the beach, and from the crags
6150	Recoiling flings in giant curves its head
6150	Aloft, and tosses high the wild sea-spray:
6150	Column on column, so the hosts of Greece
6150	Pour'd, ceaseless, to the war; to each the chiefs
6150	Their orders gave; the rest in silence mov'd:
6150	Nor would ye deem that mighty mass endued
6150	With power of speech, so silently they moved
6150	In awe of their great captains: far around
6150	Flashed the bright armour they were girt withal.
6150	""
6150	On th' other hand, the Trojans, as the flocks
6150	That in the court-yard of some wealthy Lord
6150	In countless numbers stand, at milking-time,
6150	Incessant bleating, as their lambs they hear;
6150	So rose their mingled clamours through the camp;
6150	For not one language nor one speech was there,
6150	But many nations call'd from distant lands:
6150	These Mars inspir'd, and those the blue-ey'd Maid;
6150	And Fear, and Flight, and Discord unappeas'd,
6150	Of blood-stain'd Mars the sister and the friend:
6150	"""With humble crest at first, anon her head,"
6150	"""While yet she treads the earth, affronts the skies."
6150	The gage of battle in the midst she threw,
6150	Strode through the crowd, and woe to mortals wrought.
6150	When to the midst they came, together rush'd
6150	Bucklers and lances, and the furious might
6150	Of mail-clad warriors; bossy shield on shield
6150	Clatter'd in conflict; loud the clamour rose.
6150	Then rose too mingled shouts and groans of men
6150	Slaying and slain; the earth ran red with blood.
6150	As when, descending from the mountain's brow,
6150	Two wintry torrents, from their copious source
6150	Pour downward to the narrow pass, where meet
6150	Their mingled waters in some deep ravine,
6150	Their weight of flood; on the far mountain's side
6150	The shepherd hears the roar; so loud arose
6150	The shouts and yells of those commingling hosts.
6150	""
6150	First 'mid the foremost ranks Antilochus
6150	A Trojan warrior, Echepolus, slew,
6150	A crested chief, Thalesius' noble son.
6150	Beneath his horsehair-plumed helmet's peak
6150	The sharp spear struck; deep in his forehead fix'd
6150	It pierc'd the bone; then darkness veil'd his eyes,
6150	And, like a tow'r, amid the press he fell.
6150	Him Elephenor, brave Abantian chief,
6150	Son of Chalcodon, seizing by the feet,
6150	Dragg'd from beneath the darts, in haste to strip
6150	His armour off; but short-liv'd was th' attempt;
6150	For bold Agenor mark'd him as he drew
6150	The corpse aside, and with his brass-tipp'd spear
6150	Thrust through his flank, unguarded, as he stoop'd,
6150	Beside his shield; and slack'd his limbs in death.
6150	The spirit was fled; but hotly o'er him rag'd
6150	The war of Greeks and Trojans; fierce as wolves
6150	They fought, man struggling hand to hand with man.
6150	""
6150	Then Ajax Telamon a stalwart youth,
6150	Son of Anthemion, Simoisius, slew;
6150	Whose mother gave him birth on Simois' banks,
6150	When with her parents down from Ida's heights
6150	She drove her flock; thence Simoisius nam'd:
6150	Not destined he his parents to repay
6150	Their early care; for short his term of life,
6150	By godlike Ajax' mighty spear subdued.
6150	Him, to the front advancing, in the breast,
6150	By the right nipple, Ajax struck; right through,
6150	From front to back, the brass-tipp'd spear was driv'n,
6150	Out through the shoulder; prone in dust he fell;
6150	As some tall poplar, grown in marshy mead,
6150	Smooth-stemm'd, with branches tapering tow'rd the head;
6150	Which with the biting axe the wheelwright fells,
6150	To bend the felloes of his well-built car;
6150	Sapless, beside the river, lies the tree;
6150	So lay the youthful Simoisius, felled
6150	By godlike Ajax' hand. At him, in turn,
6150	The son of Priam, Antiphus, encas'd
6150	In radiant armour, from amid the crowd
6150	His jav'lin threw; his mark, indeed, he miss'd;
6150	But through the groin Ulysses' faithful friend,
6150	Leucus, he struck, in act to bear away
6150	The youthful dead; down on the corpse he fell,
6150	And, dying, of the dead relax'd his grasp.
6150	Fierce anger, at his comrade's slaughter, filled
6150	Ulysses' breast; in burnished armour clad
6150	Forward he rush'd; and standing near, around
6150	He look'd, and pois'd on high his glitt'ring lance:
6150	Beneath his aim the Trojans back recoil'd;
6150	Nor vainly flew the spear; Democoon,
6150	A bastard son of Priam, met the blow:
6150	He from Abydos came, his high-bred mares
6150	There left to pasture; him Ulysses, fill'd
6150	With fury at his lov'd companion's death,
6150	Smote on the head; through either temple pass'd
6150	The pointed spear, and darkness veil'd his eyes.
6150	Thund'ring he fell, and loud his armour rang.
6150	At this the Trojan chiefs, and Hector's self,
6150	'Gan to give ground: the Greeks with joyful shouts
6150	Seiz'd on the dead, and forward urg'd their course.
6150	From Ilium's heights Apollo, filled with wrath,
6150	Look'd down, and to the Trojans shouted loud:
6150	"""Uprouse ye, valiant Trojans! give not way"
6150	Before the Greeks; their bodies are not stone,
6150	Nor iron, to defy your trenchant swords;
6150	And great Achilles, fair-hair'd Thetis' son,
6150	"Fights not, but o'er his anger broods apart."""
6150	So from the city call'd the heav'nly voice;
6150	The Greeks, meanwhile, all-glorious Pallas fir'd,
6150	Mov'd 'mid the tumult, and the laggards rous'd.
6150	""
6150	Then fell Diores, Amarynceus' son:
6150	A rugged fragment of a rock had crush'd
6150	His ancle and right leg; from AEnon came
6150	The Thracian chief who hurl'd it, Peirous, son
6150	Of Imbrasus; the tendons both, and bones,
6150	The huge mass shatter'd; backward in the dust
6150	He fell, both hands extending to his friends,
6150	Gasping his life away; then quick up-ran
6150	He who the blow had dealt, and with his spear
6150	Thrust through him, by the navel; from the wound
6150	His bowels gush'd, and darkness veil'd his eyes.
6150	""
6150	But he, advancing, through the breast was struck
6150	Above the nipple, by th' AEtolian chief.
6150	Thoas; and through his lungs the spear was driv'n.
6150	Thoas approach'd, and from his breast withdrew
6150	The sturdy spear, and with his sharp-edg'd sword
6150	Across his waistband gave the mortal stroke:
6150	Yet could not touch his arms; for all around
6150	The Thracian warriors, with, their tufted crowns,
6150	Their long spears held before them, him, though stout,
6150	And strong, and valiant, kept at bay; perforce
6150	He yielded; and thus side by side were laid
6150	The two, the Thracian and th' Epeian chief;
6150	And round them many a valiant soldier lay.
6150	""
6150	Well might the deeds achieved that day deserve
6150	His praise, who through that bloody field might pass
6150	By sword or spear unwounded, by the hand
6150	Of Pallas guarded from the weapon's flight;
6150	For many a Trojan, many a Greek, that day
6150	Prone in the dust, and side by side, were laid.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	ARGUMENT.
6150	""
6150	THE ACTS OF DIOMED.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	Diomed, assisted by Pallas, performs wonders in this day's battle.
6150	Pandarus wounds him with an arrow, but the goddess cures him, enables
6150	him to discern gods from mortals, and prohibits him from contending
6150	with any of the former, excepting Venus. AEneas joins Pandarus to
6150	oppose him, Pandarus is killed, and AEneas in great danger but for the
6150	assistance of Venus; who, as she is removing her son from the fight, is
6150	wounded on the hand by Diomed. Apollo seconds her in his rescue, and,
6150	at length, carries off AEneas to Troy, where he is healed in the temple
6150	of Pergamus. Mars rallies the Trojans, and assists Hector to make a
6150	stand. In the mean time AEneas is restored to the field, and they
6150	overthrow several of the Greeks; among the rest Tlepolemus is slain by
6150	Sarpedon. Juno and Minerva descend to resist Mars; the latter incites
6150	Diomed to go against that god; he wounds him, and sends him groaning to
6150	heaven.
6150	""
6150	The first battle continues through this book. The scene is the same as
6150	in the former.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	BOOK V.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	Such strength, and courage then to Diomed,
6150	The son of Tydeus, Pallas gave, as rais'd,
6150	'Mid all the Greeks, the glory of his name.
6150	Forth from his helm arid shield a fiery light
6150	There flash'd, like autumn's star, that brightest shines
6150	When newly risen from his ocean bath.
6150	So from the warrior's head and shoulders flash'd
6150	That fiery light, as to the midst he urg'd
6150	His furious course, where densest masses fought.
6150	""
6150	There was one Dares 'mid the Trojan host,
6150	The priest of Vulcan, rich, of blameless life;
6150	Two gallant sons he had, Idaeus nam'd,
6150	And Phegeus, skill'd in all the points of war.
6150	These, parted from the throng, the warrior met;
6150	They on their car, while he on foot advanc'd.
6150	When near they came, first Phegeus threw his spear;
6150	O'er the left shoulder of Tydides pass'd
6150	The erring weapon's point, and miss'd its mark.
6150	His pond'rous spear in turn Tydides threw,
6150	And not in vain; on Phegeus' breast it struck,
6150	Full in the midst, and hurl'd him from the car.
6150	Idaeus from the well-wrought chariot sprang,
6150	And fled, nor durst his brother's corpse defend.
6150	Nor had he so escap'd the doom of death,
6150	But Vulcan bore him safely from the field,
6150	In darkness shrouded, that his aged sire
6150	Might not be wholly of his sons bereav'd.
6150	The car Tydides to his comrades gave,
6150	And bade them to the ships the horses drive.
6150	""
6150	Now when the Trojans Dares' sons beheld,
6150	The one in flight, the other stretch'd in death,
6150	Their spirits within them quail'd; but Pallas took
6150	The hand of Mars, and thus address'd the God:
6150	"""Mars, Mars, thou bane of mortals, blood-stain'd Lord,"
6150	Razer of cities, wherefore leave we not
6150	The Greeks and Trojans to contend, and see
6150	To which the sire of all will vict'ry give;
6150	"While we retire, and shun the wrath of Jove?"""
6150	""
6150	Thus saying, from the battle Mars she led,
6150	And plac'd him on Scamander's steepy banks.
6150	""
6150	The Greeks drove back the Trojan host; the chiefs
6150	Slew each his victim; Agamemnon first,
6150	The mighty monarch, from his chariot hurl'd
6150	Hodius, the sturdy Halizonian chief,
6150	Him, as he turn'd, between the shoulder-blades
6150	The jav'lin struck, and through his chest was driv'n;
6150	Thund'ring he fell, and loud his armour rang.
6150	""
6150	On Phaestus, Borus' son, Maeonian chief,
6150	Who from the fertile plains of Tarna came,
6150	Then sprang Idomeneus; and as he sought
6150	To mount upon his car, the Cretan King
6150	Through his right shoulder drove the pointed spear;
6150	He fell; the shades of death his eyes o'erspread,
6150	And of his arms the followers stripp'd his corpse.
6150	""
6150	The son of Atreus, Menelaus, slew
6150	Scamandrius, son of Strophius, sportsman keen,
6150	In woodcraft skilful; for his practis'd hand
6150	Had by Diana's self been taught to slay
6150	Each beast of chase the mountain forest holds.
6150	But nought avail'd him then the Archer-Queen
6150	Diana's counsels, nor his boasted art
6150	Of distant aim; for as he fled, the lance
6150	Of Menelaus, Atreus' warlike son,
