6150	Behind his neck, between the shoulder-blades,
6150	His flight arresting, through his chest was driv'n.
6150	Headlong he fell, and loud his armour rang.
6150	""
6150	Phereclus by Meriones was slain,
6150	Son of Harmonides, whose practis'd hand
6150	Knew well to fashion many a work of art;
6150	By Pallas highly favour'd; he the ships
6150	For Paris built, first origin of ill,
6150	Freighted with evil to the men of Troy,
6150	And to himself, who knew not Heav'n's decrees.
6150	Him, in his headlong flight, in hot pursuit
6150	Meriones o'ertook, and thrust his lance
6150	Through his right flank; beneath the bone was driv'n
6150	The spear, and pierc'd him through: prone on his knees,
6150	Groaning, he fell, and death his eyelids clos'd.
6150	""
6150	Meges Pedaeus slew, Antenor's son,
6150	A bastard born, but by Theano rear'd
6150	With tender care, and nurtur'd as her son,
6150	With her own children, for her husband's sake.
6150	Him, Phyleus' warrior son, approaching near,
6150	Thrust through the junction of the head and neck;
6150	Crash'd through his teeth the spear beneath the tongue;
6150	Prone in the dust he gnash'd the brazen point.
6150	""
6150	Eurypylus, Euaemon's noble son,
6150	Hypsenor slew, the worthy progeny
6150	Of Dolopion brave; Scamander's priest,
6150	And by the people as a God rever'd:
6150	Him, as he fled before him, from behind
6150	Eurypylus, Euaemon's noble son,
6150	Smote with the sword; and from the shoulder-point
6150	The brawny arm he sever'd; to the ground
6150	Down fell the gory hand; the darkling shades
6150	Of death, and rig'rous doom, his eyelids clos'd.
6150	""
6150	Thus labour'd they amid the stubborn fight;
6150	But of Tydides none might say to whom
6150	His arm belong'd, or whether with the hosts
6150	Of Troy or Greece he mingled in the fight:
6150	Hither and thither o'er the plain he rush'd,
6150	Like to a wintry stream, that brimming o'er
6150	Breaks down its barriers in its rapid course;
6150	Nor well-built bridge can stem the flood, nor fence
6150	guards the fertile fields, as down it pours
6150	Its sudden torrent, swoll'n with rain from Heav'n,
6150	And many a goodly work of man destroys:
6150	So back were borne before Tydides' might
6150	The serried ranks of Troy, nor dar'd await,
6150	Despite their numbers, his impetuous charge.
6150	""
6150	Him when Lycaon's noble son beheld
6150	Careering o'er the plain, the serried ranks
6150	Driving before him, quick at Tydeus' son
6150	He bent his bow; and onward as he rush'd,
6150	On the right shoulder, near the breastplate's joint,
6150	The stinging arrow struck; right through it pass'd,
6150	And held its way, that blood the breastplate stain'd.
6150	Then shouted loud Lycaon's noble son:
6150	"""Arouse ye, valiant Trojans, ye who goad"
6150	Your flying steeds; the bravest of the Greeks
6150	Is wounded, nor, I deem, can long withstand
6150	My weapon, if indeed from Lycia's shore
6150	"By Phoebus' counsel sent I join'd the war."""
6150	""
6150	Thus he, vain-glorious; but not so was quell'd
6150	The godlike chief; back he withdrew, and stood
6150	Beside his car, and thus to Sthenelus,
6150	The son of Capaneus, his speech address'd:
6150	"""Up, gentle son of Capaneus, descend"
6150	From off the car, and from my shoulder draw
6150	"This stinging arrow forth."" He said, and down"
6150	Leap'd from the chariot Sthenelus, and stood
6150	Beside him; and as forth he drew the shaft,
6150	Gush'd out the blood, and dyed the twisted mail.
6150	Then thus the valiant son of Tydeus pray'd:
6150	"""Hear me, thou child of aegis-bearing Jove,"
6150	Unconquer'd! if amid the deadly fight
6150	Thy friendly aid my father e'er sustain'd,
6150	Let me in turn thy favour find; and grant
6150	Within my reach and compass of my spear
6150	That man may find himself, who unawares
6150	Hath wounded me, and vainly boasting deems
6150	"I shall not long behold the light of day."""
6150	Thus pray'd the chief, and Pallas heard his pray'r;
6150	To all his limbs, to feet and hands alike,
6150	She gave fresh vigour; and with winged words,
6150	Beside him as she stood, address'd him thus:
6150	""
6150	"""Go fearless onward, Diomed, to meet"
6150	The Trojan hosts; for I within thy breast
6150	Thy father's dauntless courage have infus'd,
6150	Such as of old in Tydeus' bosom dwelt,
6150	Bold horseman, buckler-clad; and from thine eyes
6150	The film that dimm'd them I have purg'd away,
6150	That thou mayst well 'twixt Gods and men discern.
6150	If then some God make trial of thy force,
6150	With other of th' Immortals fight thou not;
6150	But should Jove's daughter Venus dare the fray
6150	"Thou needst not shun at her to cast thy spear."""
6150	""
6150	This said, the blue-ey'd Goddess disappear'd.
6150	Forthwith again amid the foremost ranks
6150	Tydides mingled; keenly as before
6150	His spirit against the Trojans burn'd to fight,
6150	With threefold fury now he sought the fray.
6150	As when a hungry lion has o'erleap'd
6150	The sheepfold; him the guardian of the flock
6150	Has wounded, not disabled; by his wound
6150	To rage excited, but not forc'd to fly,
6150	The fold he enters, scares the trembling sheep,
6150	That, closely huddled, each on other press,
6150	Then pounces on his prey, and leaps the fence:
6150	So pounc'd Tydides on the Trojan host.
6150	Astynous and Hypeiron then he slew,
6150	His people's guardian; through the breast of one
6150	He drove his spear, and with his mighty sword
6150	He smote the other on the collar-bone,
6150	The shoulder sev'ring from the neck and back.
6150	Them left he there to lie; of Abas then
6150	And Polyeidus went in hot pursuit,
6150	Sons of Eurydamas, an aged seer,
6150	Whose visions stay'd them not; but both were doom'd
6150	A prey to valiant Diomed to fall.
6150	Xanthus and Thoon then the hero slew,
6150	The sons of Phaenops, children of his age:
6150	He, worn with years, no other sons begot,
6150	Heirs of his wealth; they two together fell,
6150	And to their father left a load of grief,
6150	That from the battle they return'd not home,
6150	And distant kindred all his substance shar'd.
6150	On Chromius and Echemon next he fell,
6150	Two sons of Priam on one chariot borne;
6150	And as a lion springs upon a herd,
6150	And breaks the neck of heifer or of steer,
6150	Feeding in woodland glade; with such a spring
6150	These two, in vain resisting, from their car
6150	Tydides hurl'd; then stripp'd their arms, and bade
6150	His followers lead their horses to the ships.
6150	""
6150	Him when AEneas saw amid the ranks
6150	Dealing destruction, through the fight and throng
6150	Of spears he plung'd, if haply he might find
6150	The godlike Pandarus; Lycaon's son
6150	He found, of noble birth and stalwart form,
6150	And stood before him, and address'd him thus:
6150	"""Where, Pandarus, are now thy winged shafts,"
6150	Thy bow, and well-known skill, wherein with thee
6150	Can no man here contend? nor Lycia boasts,
6150	Through all her wide-spread plains, a truer aim;
6150	Then raise to Jove thy hands, and with thy shaft
6150	Strike down this chief, whoe'er he be, that thus
6150	Is making fearful havoc in our host,
6150	Relaxing many a warrior's limbs in death:
6150	If he be not indeed a God, incens'd
6150	Against the Trojans for neglected rites;
6150	"For fearful is the vengeance of a God."""
6150	""
6150	Whom answer'd thus Lycaon's noble son:
6150	"""AEneas, chief and councillor of Troy,"
6150	Most like in all respects to Tydeus' son
6150	He seems; his shield I know, and visor'd helm,
6150	And horses; whether he himself be God,
6150	I cannot tell; but if he be indeed
6150	The man I think him, Tydeus' valiant son,
6150	He fights not thus without the aid of Heav'n;
6150	But by his side, his shoulders veiled in cloud,
6150	Some God attends his steps, and turns away
6150	The shaft that just hath reach'd him; for ev'n now
6150	A shaft I shot, which by the breastplate's joint
6150	Pierc'd his right shoulder through: full sure I deem'd
6150	That shaft had sent him to the shades, and yet
6150	It slew him not; 'tis sure some angry God.
6150	Nor horse have I, nor car on which to mount;
6150	But in my sire Lycaon's wealthy house
6150	Elev'n fair chariots stand, all newly built,
6150	Each with its cover; by the side of each
6150	Two steeds on rye and barley white are fed;
6150	And in his well-built house, when here I came,
6150	Lycaon, aged warrior, urg'd me oft
6150	With horses and with chariots high upborne,
6150	To lead the Trojans in the stubborn fight;
6150	I hearken'd not--'twere better if I had--
6150	Yet fear'd I lest my horses, wont to feed
6150	In plenty unstinted, by the soldiers' wants
6150	Might of their custom'd forage be depriv'd;
6150	I left them there, and hither came on foot,
6150	And trusting to my bow: vain trust, it seems;
6150	Two chiefs already have I struck, the sons
6150	Of Tydeus and of Atreus; with true aim
6150	Drawn blood from both, yet but increas'd their rage.
6150	Sad was the hour when down from where it hung
6150	I took my bow, and hasting to the aid
6150	Of godlike Hector, hither led my troops;
6150	But should I e'er return, and see again
6150	My native land, my wife, my lofty hall,
6150	Then may a stranger's sword cut off my head,
6150	If with these hands I shatter not, and burn,
6150	"The bow that thus hath fail'd me at my need."""
6150	""
6150	Him answer'd thus AEneas, chief of Troy:
6150	"""Speak thou not thus; our fortunes shall not change"
6150	Till thou and I, with chariot and with horse,
6150	This chief encounter, and his prowess prove;
6150	Then mount my car, and see how swift my steeds.
6150	Hither and thither, in pursuit or flight,
6150	From those of Tros descended, scour the plain.
6150	So if the victory to Diomed,
6150	The son of Tydeus, should by Jove be giv'n,
6150	We yet may safely reach the walls of Troy.
6150	Take thou the whip and reins, while I descend
6150	To fight on foot; or thou the chief engage,
6150	"And leave to me the conduct of the car."""
6150	""
6150	Whom answer'd thus Lycaon's noble son:
6150	"""AEneas, of thy horses and thy car"
6150	Take thou the charge; beneath th' accustomed hand,
6150	With more assurance would they draw the car,
6150	If we from Tydeus' son be forced to fly;
6150	Nor, struck with panic, and thy voice unheard,
6150	Refuse to bear us from the battle-field;
6150	So should ourselves be slain, and Tydeus' son
6150	In triumph drive thy horses to the ships.
6150	But thou thy horses and thy chariot guide,
6150	"While I his onset with my lance receive."""
6150	""
6150	Thus saying, on the car they mounted both,
6150	And tow'rd Tydides urg'd their eager steeds.
6150	Them Sthenelus beheld, the noble son
6150	Of Capaneus, and to Tydides cried:
6150	"""Oh son of Tydeus, dearest to my soul,"
6150	Two men I see, of might invincible,
6150	Impatient to engage thee; Pandarus,
6150	Well skill'd in archery, Lycaon's son;
6150	With him. AEneas, great Anchises' son,
6150	Who from immortal Venus boasts his birth.
6150	Then let us timely to the car retreat,
6150	Lest, moving thus amid the foremost ranks,
6150	"Thy daring pay the forfeit of thy life."""
6150	""
6150	To whom brave Diomed with stern regard:
6150	"""Talk not to me of flight! I heed thee not!"
6150	It is not in my nature so to fight
6150	With skulking artifice and faint retreat;
6150	My strength is yet unbroken; I should shame
6150	To mount the car; but forward will I go
6150	To meet these chiefs' encounter; for my soul
6150	Pallas forbids the touch of fear to know.
6150	Nor shall their horses' speed procure for both
6150	A safe return, though one escape my arm.
6150	This too I say, and bear my words in mind;
6150	By Pallas' counsel if my hap should be
6150	To slay them both, leave thou my horses here,
6150	The reins attaching to the chariot-rail,
6150	And seize, and from the Trojans to the ships
6150	Drive off the horses in AEneas' car;
6150	From those descended, which all-seeing Jove
6150	On Tros, for Ganymede his son, bestow'd:
6150	With these may none beneath the sun compare.
6150	Anchises, King of men, the breed obtain'd
6150	By cunning, to the horses sending mares
6150	Without the knowledge of Laomedon.
6150	Six colts were thus engender'd: four of these
6150	In his own stalls he rear'd; the other two
6150	Gave to AEneas, fear-inspiring chief:
6150	"These could we win, our praise were great indeed."""
6150	""
6150	Such converse while they held, the twain approach'd,
6150	Their horses urg'd to speed; then thus began,
6150	To Diomed, Lycaon's noble son:
6150	""
6150	"""Great son of Tydeus, warrior brave and skill'd,"
6150	My shaft, it seems, has fail'd to reach thy life;
6150	"Try we then now what hap attends my spear."""
6150	He said; and, poising, hurl'd his pond'rous spear,
6150	And struck Tydides' shield; right through the shield
6150	Drove the keen weapon, and the breastplate reach'd.
6150	Then shouted loud Lycaon's noble son:
6150	"""Thou hast it through the flank, nor canst thou long"
6150	"Survive the blow; great glory now is mine."""
6150	""
6150	To whom, unmov'd, the valiant Diomed:
6150	"""Thine aim hath failed, I am not touch'd; and now"
6150	I deem we part not hence till one of ye
6150	"Glut with his blood th' insatiate Lord of War."""
6150	""
6150	He said: the spear, by Pallas guided, struck
6150	Beside the nostril, underneath the eye;
6150	Crash'd thro' the teeth, and cutting thro' the tongue
6150	Beneath the angle of the jaw came forth:
6150	Down from the car he fell; and loudly rang
6150	His glitt'ring arms: aside the startled steeds
6150	Sprang devious: from his limbs the spirit fled.
6150	Down leap'd AEneas, spear and shield in hand,
6150	Against the Greeks to guard the valiant dead;
6150	And like a lion, fearless in his strength,
6150	Around the corpse he stalk'd, this way and that,
6150	His spear and buckler round before him held,
6150	To all who dar'd approach him threat'ning death,
6150	With fearful shouts; a rocky fragment then
6150	Tydides lifted up, a mighty mass,
6150	Which scarce two men could raise, as men are now:
6150	But he, unaided, lifted it with ease.
6150	With this he smote AEneas near the groin,
6150	Where the thigh-bone, inserted in the hip,
6150	Turns in the socket-joint; the rugged mass
6150	The socket crush'd, and both the tendons broke,
6150	And tore away the flesh: down on his knees,
6150	Yet resting on his hand, the hero fell;
6150	And o'er his eyes the shades of darkness spread.
6150	Then had AEneas, King of men, been slain,
6150	Had not his mother, Venus, child of Jove,
6150	Who to Anchises, where he fed his flocks,
6150	The hero bore, his peril quickly seen:
6150	Around her son she threw her snowy arms,
6150	And with a veil, thick-folded, wrapt him round,
6150	From hostile spears to guard him, lest some Greek
6150	Should pierce his breast, and rob him of his life.
6150	""
6150	She from the battle thus her son removed;
6150	Nor did the son of Capaneus neglect
6150	The strict injunction by Tydides giv'n;
6150	His reins attaching to the chariot-rail,
6150	Far from the battle-din he check'd, and left,
6150	His own fleet steeds; then rushing forward, seiz'd,
6150	And from the Trojans tow'rd the camp drove off,
6150	The sleek-skinn'd horses of AEneas' car.
6150	These to Deipylus, his chosen friend,
6150	He gave, of all his comrades best esteem'd,
6150	Of soundest judgment, tow'rd the ships to drive.
6150	Then, his own car remounting, seiz'd the reins,
6150	And urg'd with eager haste his fiery steeds,
6150	Seeking Tydides; he, meanwhile, press'd on
6150	In keen pursuit of Venus; her he knew
6150	A weak, unwarlike Goddess, not of those
6150	That like Bellona fierce, or Pallas, range
6150	Exulting through the blood-stain'd fields of war.
6150	""
6150	Her, searching thro' the crowd, at length he found,
6150	And springing forward, with his pointed spear
6150	A wound inflicted on her tender hand.
6150	Piercing th' ambrosial veil, the Graces' work,
6150	The sharp spear graz'd her palm below the wrist.
6150	Forth from the wound th' immortal current flow'd,
6150	Pure ichor, life-stream of the blessed Gods;
6150	They eat no bread, they drink no ruddy wine,
6150	And bloodless thence and deathless they become.
6150	The Goddess shriek'd aloud, and dropp'd her son;
6150	But in his arms Apollo bore him off
6150	In a thick cloud envelop'd, lest some Greek
6150	Might pierce his breast, and rob him of his life.
6150	Loud shouted brave Tydides, as she fled:
6150	"""Daughter of Jove, from battle-fields retire;"
6150	Enough for thee weak woman to delude;
6150	If war thou seek'st, the lesson thou shalt learn
6150	"Shall cause thee shudder but to hear it nam'd."""
6150	Thus he; but ill at ease, and sorely pain'd,
6150	The Goddess fled: her, Iris, swift as wind,
6150	Caught up, and from the tumult bore away,
6150	Weeping with pain, her fair skin soil'd with blood.
6150	""
6150	Mars on the left hand of the battle-field
6150	She found, his spear reclining by his side,
6150	And, veil'd in cloud, his car and flying steeds.
6150	Kneeling, her brother she besought to lend
6150	The flying steeds, with golden frontlets crown'd:
6150	"""Dear brother, aid me hence, and lend thy car"
6150	To bear me to Olympus, seat of Gods;
6150	Great is the pain I suffer from a wound
6150	Receiv'd from Diomed, a mortal man,
6150	"Who now would dare with Jove himself to fight."""
6150	""
6150	He lent the steeds, with golden frontlets crown'd;
6150	In deep distress she mounted on the car:
6150	Beside her Iris stood, and took the reins,
6150	And urg'd the coursers; nothing loth they flew,
6150	And soon to high Olympus, seat of Gods,
6150	They came: swift Iris there the coursers stay'd,
6150	Loos'd from the chariot, and before them plac'd
6150	Ambrosial forage: on her mother's lap,
6150	Dione, Venus fell; she in her arms
6150	Embrac'd, and sooth'd her with her hand, and said:
6150	"""Which of the heav'nly pow'rs hath wrong'd thee thus,"
6150	"My child, as guilty of some open shame?"""
6150	""
6150	Whom answer'd thus the laughter-loving Queen;
6150	"""The haughty son of Tydeus, Diomed,"
6150	Hath wounded me, because my dearest son,
6150	AEneas, from the field I bore away.
6150	No more 'twixt Greeks and Trojans is the fight,
6150	"But with the Gods themselves the Greeks contend."""
6150	To whom Dione, heav'nly Goddess, thus:
6150	"""Have patience, dearest child; though much enforc'd,"
6150	Restrain thine anger: we, in Heav'n who dwell,
6150	Have much to bear from mortals; and ourselves
6150	Too oft upon each other suff'rings lay.
6150	Mars had his suff'rings; by Aloeus' sons,
6150	Otus and Ephialtes, strongly bound,
6150	He thirteen months in brazen fetters lay:
6150	And there had pin'd away the God of War,
6150	Insatiate Mars, had not their step-mother,
6150	The beauteous Eriboea, sought the aid
6150	Of Hermes; he by stealth releas'd the God,
6150	Sore worn and wasted by his galling chains.
6150	Juno too suffer'd, when Amphitryon's son
6150	Through her right breast a three-barb'd arrow sent:
6150	Dire, and unheard of, were the pangs she bore.
6150	Great Pluto's self the stinging arrow felt,
6150	When that same son of aegis-bearing Jove
6150	Assail'd him in the very gates of hell,
6150	And wrought him keenest anguish; pierc'd with pain
6150	To high Olympus, to the courts of Jove,
6150	Groaning, he came; the bitter shaft remain'd
6150	Deep in his shoulder fix'd, and griev'd his soul.
6150	But soon with soothing ointments Paeon's hand
6150	(For death on him was powerless) heal'd the wound.
6150	Accurs'd was he, of daring over-bold,
6150	Reckless of evil deeds, who with his bow
6150	Assail'd the Gods, who on Olympus dwell.
6150	The blue-ey'd Pallas, well I know, has urg'd
6150	Tydides to assail thee; fool and blind!
6150	Unknowing he how short his term of life
6150	Who fights against the Gods! for him no child
6150	Upon his knees shall lisp a father's name,
6150	Safe from the war and battle-field return'd.
6150	Brave as he is, let Diomed beware
6150	He meet not some more dangerous foe than thee.
6150	Then fair AEgiale, Adrastus' child,
6150	The noble wife of valiant Diomed,
6150	Shall long, with lamentations loud, disturb
6150	The slumbers of her house, and vainly mourn
6150	"Her youthful Lord, the bravest of the Greeks."""
6150	""
6150	She said; and wip'd the ichor from, the wound;
6150	he hand was heal'd, the grievous pains allay'd.
6150	But Juno and Minerva, looking on,
6150	With words of bitter mock'ry Saturn's son
6150	Provok'd: and thus the blue-ey'd Goddess spoke:
6150	"""O Father! may I speak without offence?"
6150	Venus, it seems, has sought to lead astray
6150	Some Grecian woman, and persuade to join
6150	Those Trojans, whom she holds in high esteem;
6150	And, as her hand the gentle dame caress'd,
6150	"A golden clasp has scratched her slender arm."""
6150	""
6150	Thus she: and smil'd the Sire of Gods and men;
6150	He call'd the golden Venus to his side,
6150	"And, ""Not to thee, my child,"" he said, ""belong"
6150	The deeds of war; do thou bestow thy care
6150	On deeds of love, and tender marriage ties;
6150	"But leave to Mars and Pallas feats of arms."""
6150	""
6150	Such converse while they held, brave Diomed
6150	Again assail'd AEneas; well he knew
6150	Apollo's guardian hand around him thrown;
6150	Yet by the God undaunted, on he press'd
6150	To slay AEneas, and his arms obtain.
6150	Thrice was his onset made, with murd'rous aim;
6150	And thrice Apollo struck his glitt'ring shield;
6150	But when, with godlike force, he sought to make
6150	His fourth attempt, the Far-destroyer spoke
6150	"In terms of awful menace: ""Be advis'd,"
6150	Tydides, and retire; nor as a God
6150	Esteem thyself; since not alike the race
6150	"Of Gods immortal and of earth-born men."""
6150	""
6150	He said; and Diomed a little space
6150	Before the Far-destroyer's wrath retir'd:
6150	Apollo then AEneas bore away
6150	Far from the tumult; and in Pergamus,
6150	Where stood his sacred shrine, bestow'd him safe.
6150	Latona there, and Dian, Archer-Queen,
6150	In the great temple's innermost recess,
6150	Gave to his wounds their care, and sooth'd his pride.
6150	Meanwhile Apollo of the silver bow
6150	A phantom form prepar'd, the counterpart
6150	Of great AEneas, and alike in arms:
6150	Around the form, of Trojans and of Greeks,
6150	Loud was the din of battle; fierce the strokes
6150	That fell on rounded shield of tough bull's-hide,
6150	And lighter targe, before each warrior's breast.
6150	Then thus Apollo to the God of War:
6150	"""Mars! Mars! thou bane of mortals, blood-stain'd Lord,"
6150	Razer of cities, wer't not well thyself
6150	To interpose, and from the battle-field
6150	Withdraw this chief, Tydides? such his pride,
6150	He now would dare with Jove himself to fight.
6150	Venus, of late, he wounded in the wrist;
6150	"And, like a God, but now confronted me."""
6150	He said, and sat on Ilium's topmost height:
6150	While Mars, in likeness of the Thracian chief,
6150	Swift Acamas, amid the Trojan ranks
6150	Mov'd to and fro, and urg'd them to the fight.
6150	To Priam's Heav'n-descended sons he call'd;
6150	"""Ye sons of Priam, Heav'n-descended King,"
6150	How long will ye behold your people slain?
6150	Till to your very doors the war be brought?
6150	AEneas, noble-soul'd Anchises' son,
6150	In like esteem with Hector held, is down;
6150	"On to his aid! our gallant comrade save!"""
6150	""
6150	He said; his words fresh courage gave to all:
6150	Then thus Sarpedon, in reproachful tone,
6150	"Address'd the godlike Hector; ""Where is now,"
6150	Hector, the spirit that heretofore was thine?
6150	'Twas once thy boast that ev'n without allies
6150	Thyself, thy brethren, and thy house, alone
6150	The city could defend: for all of these
6150	I look in vain, and see not one; they all,
6150	As curs around a lion, cow'r and crouch:
6150	We, strangers and allies, maintain the fight.
6150	I to your aid, from lands afar remote,
6150	From Lycia came, by Xanthus' eddying stream;
6150	There left a cherish'd wife, and infant son,
6150	And rich possessions, which might envy move;
6150	Yet I my troops encourage; and myself
6150	Have play'd my part, though nought have I to lose,
6150	Nought that the Greeks could drive or bear away;
6150	But thou stand'st idly by; nor bidd'st the rest
6150	Maintain their ground, and guard their wives and homes.
6150	Beware lest ye, as in the meshes caught
6150	Of some wide-sweeping net, become the prey
6150	And booty of your foes, who soon shall lay
6150	Your prosp'rous city level with the dust.
6150	By day and night should this thy thoughts engage,
6150	With constant pray'r to all thy brave allies,
6150	"Firmly to stand, and wipe this shame away."""
6150	""
6150	He said; and Hector felt the biting speech;
6150	Down from his car he leap'd; and through the ranks,
6150	Two jav'lins brandishing, he pass'd, to arms
6150	Exciting all, and rais'd his battle-cry.
6150	The tide was turn'd; again they fac'd the Greeks:
6150	In serried ranks the Greeks, undaunted, stood.
6150	As when the wind from off a threshing-floor,
6150	Where men are winnowing, blows the chaff away;
6150	When yellow Ceres with the breeze divides
6150	The corn and chaff, which lies in whit'ning heaps;
6150	So thick the Greeks were whiten'd o'er with dust,
6150	Which to the brazen vault of Heav'n arose
6150	Beneath the horses' feet, that with the crowd
6150	Were mingled, by their drivers turn'd to flight.
6150	Unwearied still, they bore the brunt; but Mars
6150	The Trojans succouring, the battle-field
6150	Veil'd in thick clouds, from ev'ry quarter brought.
6150	Thus he of Phoebus of the golden sword
6150	Obey'd th' injunction, bidding him arouse
6150	The courage of the Trojans, when he saw
6150	Pallas approaching to support the Greeks.
6150	""
6150	Then from the wealthy shrine Apollo's self
6150	AEneas brought, and vigour fresh infus'd:
6150	Amid his comrades once again he stood;
6150	They joy'd to see him yet alive, and sound,
6150	And full of vigour; yet no question ask'd:
6150	No time for question then, amid the toils
6150	Impos'd by Phoebus of the silver bow,
6150	And blood-stain'd Mars, and Discord unappeas'd.
6150	""
6150	Meanwhile Ulysses, and th' Ajaces both,
6150	And Diomed, with courage for the fight
6150	The Grecian force inspir'd; they undismay'd
6150	Shrank not before the Trojans' rush and charge;
6150	In masses firm they stood, as when the clouds
6150	Are gather'd round the misty mountain top
6150	By Saturn's son, in breathless calm, while sleep
6150	The force of Boreas and the stormy winds,
6150	That with their breath the shadowy clouds disperse;
6150	So stood the Greeks, nor shunn'd the Trojans' charge.
6150	Through all the army Agamemnon pass'd,
6150	"And cried, ""Brave comrades, quit ye now like men;"
6150	Bear a stout heart; and in the stubborn fight,
6150	Let each to other mutual succour give;
6150	By mutual succour more are sav'd than fall;
6150	"In timid flight nor fame nor safety lies."""
6150	""
6150	Thus he: and straight his jav'lin threw, and struck
6150	A man of mark, AEneas' faithful friend,
6150	Deicoon, the son of Pergasus,
6150	By Troy, as ever foremost in the field,
6150	In equal honour held with Priam's sons.
6150	His shield the monarch Agamemnon struck;
6150	The shield's defence was vain; the spear pass'd through
6150	Beneath the belt, and in his groin was lodg'd;
6150	Thund'ring he fell, and loud his armour rang.
6150	""
6150	On th' other side, AEneas slew two chiefs,
6150	The bravest of the Greeks, Orsilochus
6150	And Crethon, sons of Diocles, who dwelt
6150	In thriving Phera; rich in substance he,
6150	And from the mighty River Alpheus trac'd
6150	His high descent, who through the Pylian land
6150	His copious waters pours; to him was born
6150	Orsilochus, of num'rous tribes the chief;
6150	To him succeeded valiant Diocles;
6150	To whom were born twin sons, Orsilochus
6150	And Crethon, skill'd in ev'ry point of war.
6150	They, in the vigour of their youth, to Troy
6150	Had sail'd amid the dark-ribb'd ships of Greece,
6150	Of Atreus' sons the quarrel to uphold;
6150	But o'er them both the shades of death were spread.
6150	As two young lions, by their tawny dam
6150	Nurs'd in the mountain forest's deep recess,
6150	On flocks and herds their youthful fury pour,
6150	With havoc to the sheepfolds, till themselves
6150	Succumb, o'ermaster'd by the hand of man:
6150	So fell these two beneath AEneas' hand,
6150	And like two lofty pines in death they lay.
6150	""
6150	The warlike Menelaus saw their fall
6150	With pitying eye; and through the foremost ranks
6150	With brandish'd spear advanc'd, by Mars impell'd,
6150	Who hop'd his death by great AEneas' hand.
6150	Him Nestor's son, Antilochus, beheld,
6150	And hasten'd to his aid; for much he fear'd
6150	Lest ill befall the monarch, and his death
6150	Deprive them of their warlike labours' fruit.
6150	They two, with force combined of hand and spear,
6150	Press'd onward to the fight; Antilochus
6150	His station keeping close beside the King.
6150	Before the two combined, AEneas fear'd,
6150	Bold warrior as he was, to hold his ground.
6150	The slain they drew within the Grecian lines,
6150	Placed in their comrades' hands, and turning back
6150	Amid the foremost mingled in the fray.
6150	Then, brave as Mars, Pylaemenes they slew,
6150	The buckler'd Paphlagonians' warlike chief;
6150	Him Menelaus, hand to hand engag'd,
6150	Pierc'd with a spear-thrust through the collar-bone;
6150	While, with a pond'rous stone, Antilochus
6150	Full on the elbow smote Atymnius' son,
6150	Mydon, his charioteer, in act to turn
6150	His fiery steeds to flight; down from his hands
6150	Fell to the ground the iv'ry-mounted reins.
6150	On rush'd Antilochus, and with his sword
6150	Across the temples smote him; gasping, he
6150	Upon his neck and shoulders from the car
6150	Pitch'd headlong; and (for there the sand was deep)
6150	Awhile stood balanc'd, till the horses' feet
6150	Dash'd him upon the ground; Antilochus,
6150	The horses seizing, drove them to the ships.
6150	""
6150	Hector beheld athwart the ranks, and rush'd,
6150	Loud shouting, to th' encounter; at his back
6150	Follow'd the thronging bands of Troy, by Mars
6150	And fierce Bellona led; she by the hand
6150	Wild Uproar held; while Mars a giant spear
6150	Brandish'd aloft: and stalking now before,
6150	Now following after Hector, urg'd them on.
6150	Quail'd at the sight the valiant Diomed:
6150	As when a man, long journeying o'er the plain,
6150	All unprepar'd, stands sudden on the brink
6150	Of a swift stream, down rushing to the sea,
6150	Boiling with foam, and back recoils; so then
6150	Recoil'd Tydides, and address'd the crowd:
6150	"""O friends, we marvel at the might display'd"
6150	By Hector, spearman skill'd and warrior bold;
6150	But still some guardian God his steps attends,
6150	And shields from danger; now beside him stands,
6150	In likeness of a mortal, Mars himself.
6150	Then turning still your faces to your foes,
6150	"Retire, nor venture with the Gods to fight."""
6150	""
6150	He said; the Trojans now were close at hand,
6150	And, mounted both upon a single car,
6150	Two chiefs, Menesthes and Anchialus,
6150	Well skill'd in war, by Hector's hand were slain.
6150	""
6150	With pitying eyes great Ajax Telamon
6150	Beheld their fall; advancing close, he threw
6150	His glitt'ring spear; the son of Selagus
6150	It struck, Amphius, who in Paesus dwelt,
6150	In land and substance rich; by evil fate
6150	Impell'd, to Priam's house he brought his aid.
6150	Below the belt the spear of Ajax struck,
6150	And in his groin the point was buried deep;
6150	Thund'ring he fell; then forward Ajax sprang
6150	To seize the spoils of war; but fast and fierce
6150	The Trojans show'r'd their weapons bright and keen,
6150	And many a lance the mighty shield receiv'd.
6150	Ajax, his foot firm planted on the slain,
6150	Withdrew the brazen spear; yet could not strip
6150	His armour off, so galling flew the shafts;
6150	And much he fear'd his foes might hem him in,
6150	Who closely press'd upon him, many and brave;
6150	And, valiant as he was, and tall, and strong,
6150	Still drove him backward; he perforce retired.
6150	""
6150	Thus labour'd they amid the stubborn fight.
6150	Then evil fate induc'd Tlepolemus,
6150	Valiant and strong, the son of Hercules,
6150	Heav'n-born Sarpedon to confront in fight.
6150	When near they came, of cloud-compelling Jove
6150	Grandson and son, Tlepolemus began:
6150	"""Sarpedon, Lycian chief, what brings thee here,"
6150	Trembling and crouching, all unskill'd in war?
6150	Falsely they speak who fable thee the son
6150	Of aegis-bearing Jove; so far art thou
6150	Beneath their mark who claim'd in elder days
6150	That royal lineage: such my father was,
6150	Of courage resolute, of lion heart.
6150	With but six ships, and with a scanty band,
6150	The horses by Laomedon withheld
6150	Avenging, he o'erthrew this city, Troy,
6150	And made her streets a desert; but thy soul
6150	Is poor, thy troops are wasting fast away;
6150	Nor deem I that the Trojans will in thee
6150	(Ev'n were thy valour more) and Lycia's aid
6150	Their safeguard find; but vanquish'd by my hand,
6150	"This day the gates of Hades thou shalt pass."""
6150	""
6150	To whom the Lycian chief, Sarpedon, thus:
6150	"""Tlepolemus, the sacred walls of Troy"
6150	Thy sire o'erthrew, by folly of one man,
6150	Laomedon, who with injurious words
6150	His noble service recompens'd; nor gave
6150	The promis'd steeds, for which he came from far.
6150	For thee, I deem thou now shalt meet thy doom
6150	Here, at my hand; on thee my spear shall win
6150	"Renown for me, thy soul to Hades send."""
6150	""
6150	Thus as Sarpedon spoke, Tlepolemus
6150	Uprais'd his ashen spear; from both their hands
6150	The pond'rous weapons simultaneous flew.
6150	Full in the throat Tlepolemus receiv'd
6150	Sarpedon's spear; right through the neck it pass'd,
6150	And o'er his eyes the shades of death were spread.
6150	On th' other side his spear Sarpedon struck
6150	On the left thigh; the eager weapon pass'd
6150	Right through the flesh, and in the bone was fix'd;
6150	The stroke of death his father turn'd aside.
6150	Sarpedon from the field his comrades bore,
6150	Weigh'd down and tortured by the trailing spear,
6150	For, in their haste to bear him to his car,
6150	Not one bethought him from his thigh to draw
6150	The weapon forth; so sorely were they press'd.
6150	""
6150	The Greeks too from the battle-field convey'd
6150	The slain Tlepolemus; Ulysses saw,
6150	Patient of spirit, but deeply mov'd at heart;
6150	And with conflicting thoughts his breast was torn,
6150	If first he should pursue the Thund'rer's son,
6150	Or deal destruction on the Lycian host.
6150	But fate had not decreed the valiant son
6150	Of Jove to fall beneath Ulysses' hand;
6150	So on the Lycians Pallas turn'd his wrath.
6150	Alastor then, and Coeranus he slew,
6150	Chromius, Alcander, Halius, Prytanis,
6150	Noemon; nor had ended then the list
6150	Of Lycian warriors by Ulysses slain;
6150	But Hector of the glancing helm beheld;
6150	Through the front ranks he rush'd, with burnish'd crest
6150	Resplendent, flashing terror on the Greeks;
6150	With joy Sarpedon saw his near approach,
6150	And with imploring tones address'd him thus:
6150	""
6150	"""Hector, thou son of Priam, leave me not"
6150	A victim to the Greeks, but lend thine aid:
6150	Then in your city let me end my days.
6150	For not to me is giv'n again to see
6150	My native land; or, safe returning home,
6150	"To glad my sorrowing wife and infant child."""
6150	""
6150	Thus he; but Hector, answ'ring not a word,
6150	Pass'd on in silence, hasting to pursue
6150	The Greeks, and pour destruction on their host.
6150	""
6150	Beneath the oak of aegis-bearing Jove
6150	His faithful comrades laid Sarpedon down,
6150	And from his thigh the valiant Pelagon,
6150	His lov'd companion, drew the ashen spear.
6150	He swoon'd, and giddy mists o'erspread his eyes:
6150	But soon reviv'd, as on his forehead blew,
6150	While yet he gasp'd for breath, the cooling breeze.
6150	""
6150	By Mars and Hector of the brazen helm
6150	The Greeks hard-press'd, yet fled not to their ships,
6150	Nor yet sustain'd the fight; but back retir'd
6150	Soon as they learned the presence of the God.
6150	Say then who first, who last, the prowess felt
6150	Of Hector, Priam's son, and mail-clad Mars?
6150	The godlike Teuthras first, Orestes next,
6150	Bold charioteer; th' AEtolian spearman skill'd,
6150	Trechus, OEnomaus, and Helenus,
6150	The son of OEnops; and Oresbius, girt
6150	With sparkling girdle; he in Hyla dwelt,
6150	The careful Lord of boundless wealth, beside
6150	Cephisus' marshy banks; Boeotia's chiefs
6150	Around him dwelt, on fat and fertile soil.
6150	Juno, the white-arm'd Queen, who saw these two
6150	The Greeks destroying in the stubborn fight,
6150	To Pallas thus her winged words address'd:
6150	"""O Heav'n! brave child of aegis-bearing Jove,"
6150	Vain was our word to Menelaus giv'n.
6150	That he the well-built walls of Troy should raze,
6150	And safe return, if unrestrain'd we leave
6150	Ferocious Mars to urge his mad career.
6150	"Come then; let us too mingle in the fray."""
6150	""
6150	She said: and Pallas, blue-ey'd Maid, complied.
6150	Offspring of Saturn, Juno, heav'nly Queen,
6150	Herself th' immortal steeds caparison'd,
6150	Adorn'd with golden frontlets: to the car
6150	Hebe the circling wheels of brass attach'd,
6150	Eight-spok'd, that on an iron axle turn'd;
6150	The felloes were of gold, and fitted round
6150	With brazen tires, a marvel to behold;
6150	The naves were silver, rounded every way:
6150	The chariot-board on gold and silver bands
6150	Was hung, and round it ran a double rail:
6150	The pole was all of silver; at the end
6150	A golden yoke, with golden yoke-bands fair:
6150	And Juno, all on fire to join the fray,
6150	Beneath the yoke the flying coursers led.
6150	""
6150	Pallas, the child of aegis-bearing Jove,
6150	Within her father's threshold dropp'd her veil,
6150	Of airy texture, work of her own hands;
6150	The cuirass donn'd of cloud-compelling Jove,
6150	And stood accoutred for the bloody fray.
6150	Her tassell'd aegis round her shoulders next
6150	She threw, with Terror circled all around;
6150	And on its face were figur'd deeds of arms,
6150	And Strife, and Courage high, and panic Rout;
6150	There too a Gorgon's head, of monstrous size,
6150	Frown'd terrible, portent of angry Jove:
6150	And on her head a golden helm she plac'd,
6150	Four-crested, double-peak'd, whose ample verge
6150	A hundred cities' champions might suffice:
6150	Her fiery car she mounted: in her hand
6150	A spear she bore, long, weighty, tough; wherewith
6150	The mighty daughter of a mighty sire
6150	Sweeps down the ranks of those her hate pursues.
6150	""
6150	Then Juno sharply touch'd the flying steeds:
6150	Forthwith spontaneous opening, grated harsh
6150	The heavenly portals, guarded by the Hours,
6150	Who Heav'n and high Olympus have in charge
6150	To roll aside, or draw the veil of cloud.
6150	Through these th' excited horses held their way.
6150	They found the son of Saturn, from the Gods
6150	Sitting apart, upon the highest crest
6150	Of many-ridg'd Olympus; there arriv'd,
6150	The white-arm'd Goddess Juno stay'd her steeds,
6150	And thus address'd the Sov'reign Lord of Heav'n:
6150	""
6150	"""O Father Jove! canst thou behold unmov'd"
6150	The violence of Mars? how many Greeks,
6150	Reckless and uncontroll'd, he hath destroy'd;
6150	To me a source of bitter grief; meanwhile
6150	Venus and Phoebus of the silver bow
6150	Look on, well pleas'd, who sent this madman forth,
6150	To whom both law and justice are unknown.
6150	Say, Father Jove, shall I thine anger move,
6150	"If with disgrace I drive him from the field?"""
6150	""
6150	To whom the Cloud-compeller thus replied:
6150	"""Go, send against him Pallas; she, I know,"
6150	"Hath oft inflicted on him grievous pain.""."
6150	""
6150	He said: the white-arm'd Queen with joy obey'd;
6150	She urg'd her horses; nothing loth, they flew
6150	Midway between the earth, and starry Heav'n:
6150	Far as his sight extends, who from on high
6150	Looks from his watch-tow'r o'er the dark-blue sea,
6150	So far at once the neighing horses bound.
6150	But when to Troy they came, beside the streams
6150	Where Simois' and Scamander's waters meet,
6150	The white-arm'd Goddess stay'd her flying steeds,
6150	Loos'd from the car, and veil'd in densest cloud.
6150	For them, at bidding of the river-God,
6150	Ambrosial forage grew: the Goddesses,
6150	Swift as the wild wood-pigeon's rapid flight,
6150	Sped to the battle-field to aid the Greeks.
6150	But when they reach'd the thickest of the fray,
6150	Where throng'd around the might of Diomed
6150	The bravest and the best, as lions fierce,
6150	Or forest-boars, the mightiest of their kind,
6150	There stood the white-arm'd Queen, and call'd aloud,
6150	In form of Stentor, of the brazen voice,
6150	Whose shout was as the shout of fifty men:
6150	""
6150	"""Shame on ye, Greeks, base cowards! brave alone"
6150	In outward semblance; while Achilles yet
6150	Went forth to battle, from the Dardan gates
6150	The Trojans never ventur'd to advance,
6150	So dreaded they his pond'rous spear; but now
6150	"Far from the walls, beside your ships, they fight."""
6150	""
6150	She said: her words their drooping courage rous'd.
6150	Meanwhile the blue-ey'd Pallas went in haste
6150	In search of Tydeus' son; beside his car
6150	She found the King, in act to cool the wound
6150	Inflicted by the shaft of Pandarus:
6150	Beneath his shield's broad belt the clogging sweat
6150	Oppress'd him, and his arm was faint with toil;
6150	The belt was lifted up, and from the wound
6150	He wip'd the clotted blood: beside the car
6150	The Goddess stood, and touch'd the yoke, and said:
6150	""
6150	"""Little like Tydeus' self is Tydeus' son:"
6150	Low was his stature, but his spirit was high:
6150	And ev'n when I from combat rashly wag'd
6150	Would fain have kept him back, what time in Thebes
6150	He found himself, an envoy and alone,
6150	Without support, among the Thebans all,
6150	I counsell'd him in peace to share the feast:
6150	But by his own impetuous courage led,
6150	He challenged all the Thebans to contend
6150	With him in wrestling, and o'erthrew them all
6150	With ease; so mighty was the aid I gave.
6150	Thee now I stand beside, and guard from harm,
6150	And bid thee boldly with the Trojans fight.
6150	But, if the labours of the battle-field
6150	O'ertask thy limbs, or heartless fear restrain,
6150	"No issue thou of valiant Tydeus' loins."""
6150	""
6150	Whom answer'd thus the valiant Diomed:
6150	"""I know thee, Goddess, who thou art; the child"
6150	Of aegis-bearing Jove: to thee my mind
6150	I freely speak, nor aught will I conceal.
6150	Nor heartless fear, nor hesitating doubt,
6150	Restrain me; but I bear thy words in mind,
6150	With other of th' Immortals not to fight:
6150	But should Jove's daughter, Venus, dare the fray,
6150	At her I need not shun to throw my spear.
6150	Therefore I thus withdrew, and others too
6150	Exhorted to retire, since Mars himself
6150	"I saw careering o'er the battle-field."""
6150	""
6150	To whom the blue-ey'd Goddess, Pallas, thus:
6150	"""Thou son of Tydeus, dearest to my soul,"
6150	Fear now no more with Mars himself to fight,
6150	Nor other God; such aid will I bestow.
6150	Come then; at him the first direct thy car;
6150	Encounter with him hand to hand; nor fear
6150	To strike this madman, this incarnate curse,
6150	This shameless renegade; who late agreed
6150	With Juno and with me to combat Troy,
6150	And aid the Grecian cause; who now appears,
6150	"The Greeks deserting, in the Trojan ranks."""
6150	""
6150	Thus Pallas spoke, and stretching forth her hand
6150	Backward his comrade Sthenelus she drew
6150	From off the chariot; down in haste he sprang.
6150	His place beside the valiant Diomed
6150	The eager Goddess took; beneath the weight
6150	Loud groan'd the oaken axle; for the car
6150	A mighty Goddess and a Hero bore.
6150	Then Pallas took the whip and reins, and urg'd
6150	Direct at Mars the fiery coursers' speed.
6150	""
6150	The bravest of th' AEtolians, Periphas,
6150	Ochesius' stalwart son, he just had slain,
6150	And stood in act to strip him of his arms.
6150	The helmet then of Darkness Pallas donn'd,
6150	To hide her presence from the sight of Mars:
6150	But when the blood-stain'd God of War beheld
6150	Advancing tow'rd him godlike Diomed,
6150	The corpse of stalwart Periphas he left,
6150	There where he fell, to lie; while he himself
6150	Of valiant Diomed th' encounter met.
6150	When near they came, first Mars his pond'rous spear
6150	Advane'd beyond the yoke and horses' reins,
6150	With murd'rous aim; but Pallas from the car
6150	Turn'd it aside, and foil'd the vain attempt.
6150	""
6150	Then Diomed thrust forward in his turn
6150	His pond'rous spear; low on the flank of Mars,
6150	Guided by Pallas, with successful aim,
6150	Just where the belt was girt, the weapon struck:
6150	It pierc'd the flesh, and straight was back withdrawn:
6150	Then Mars cried out aloud, with such a shout
6150	As if nine thousand or ten thousand men
6150	Should simultaneous raise their battle-cry:
6150	Trojans and Greeks alike in terror heard,
6150	Trembling; so fearful was the cry of Mars.
6150	As black with clouds appears the darken'd air,
6150	When after heat the blust'ring winds arise,
6150	So Mars to valiant Diomed appear'd,
6150	As in thick clouds lie took his heav'nward flight.
6150	With speed he came to great Olympus' heights,
6150	Th' abode of Gods; and sitting by the throne
6150	Of Saturn's son, with anguish torn, he show'd
6150	Th' immortal stream that trickled from the wound,
6150	And thus to Jove his piteous words address'd:
6150	""
6150	"""O Father Jove, canst thou behold unmov'd"
6150	These acts of violence? the greatest ills
6150	We Gods endure, we each to other owe
6150	Who still in human quarrels interpose.
6150	Of thee we all complain; thy senseless child
6150	Is ever on some evil deed intent.
6150	The other Gods, who on Olympus dwell,
6150	Are all to thee obedient and submiss;
6150	But thy pernicious daughter, nor by word
6150	Nor deed dost thou restrain; who now excites
6150	Th' o'erbearing son of Tydeus, Diomed,
6150	Upon th' immortal Gods to vent his rage.
6150	Venus of late he wounded in the wrist,
6150	And, as a God, but now encounter'd me:
6150	Barely I 'scap'd by swiftness of my feet;
6150	Else, 'mid a ghastly heap of corpses slain,
6150	In anguish had I lain; and, if alive,
6150	"Yet liv'd disabl'd by his weapon's stroke."""
6150	""
6150	Whom answer'd thus the Cloud-compeller, Jove,
6150	"With look indignant: ""Come no more to me,"
6150	Thou wav'ring turncoat, with thy whining pray'rs:
6150	Of all the Gods who on Olympus dwell
6150	I hate thee most; for thou delight'st in nought
6150	But strife and war; thou hast inherited
6150	Thy mother, Juno's, proud, unbending mood,
6150	Whom I can scarce control; and thou, methinks,
6150	To her suggestions ow'st thy present plight.
6150	Yet since thou art my offspring, and to me
6150	Thy mother bore thee, I must not permit
6150	That thou should'st long be doom'd to suffer pain;
6150	But had thy birth been other than it is,
6150	For thy misdoings thou hadst long ere now
6150	"Been banish'd from the Gods' companionship."""
6150	""
6150	He said: and straight to Paeon gave command
6150	To heal the wound; with soothing anodynes
6150	He heal'd it quickly; soon as liquid milk
6150	Is curdled by the fig-tree's juice, and turns
6150	In whirling flakes, so soon was heal'd the wound.
6150	By Hebe bath'd, and rob'd afresh, he sat
6150	In health and strength restor'd, by Saturn's son.
6150	""
6150	Mars thus arrested in his murd'rous course,
6150	Together to th' abode of Jove return'd
6150	The Queen of Argos and the blue-ey'd Maid.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	ARGUMENT.
6150	""
6150	THE EPISODES OF GLAUCUS AND DIOMED, AND OF HECTOR AND ANDROMACHE.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	The gods having left the field, the Grecians prevail. Helenus, the
6150	chief augur of Troy, commands Hector to return to the city, in order to
6150	appoint a solemn procession of the Queen and the Trojan matrons to the
6150	temple of Minerva, to entreat her to remove Diomed from the fight. The
6150	battle relaxing during the absence of Hector, Glaucus and Diomed have
6150	an interview between the two armies; where, coming to the knowledge of
6150	the friendship and hospitality past between their ancestors, they make
6150	exchange of their arms. Hector, having performed the orders of Helenus,
6150	prevailed upon Paris to return to the battle, and taken a tender leave
6150	of his wife Andromache, hastens again to the field.
6150	""
6150	The scene is first in the field of battle, between the rivers Simois
6150	and Scamander, and then changes to Troy.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	BOOK VI.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	The Gods had left the field, and o'er the plain
6150	Hither and thither surg'd the tide of war,
6150	As couch'd th' opposing chiefs their brass-tipp'd spears,
6150	Midway 'twixt Simois' and Scamander's streams.
6150	""
6150	First through the Trojan phalanx broke his way
6150	The son of Telamon, the prop of Greece,
6150	The mighty Ajax; on his friends the light
6150	Of triumph shedding, as Eusorus' son
6150	He smote, the noblest of the Thracian bands,
6150	Valiant and strong, the gallant Acamas.
6150	Full in the front, beneath the plumed helm,
6150	The sharp spear struck, and crashing thro' the bone,
6150	The warrior's eyes were clos'd in endless night.
6150	""
6150	Next valiant Diomed Axylus slew,
6150	The son of Teuthranes, who had his home
6150	In fair Arisba; rich in substance he,
6150	And lov'd of all; for, dwelling near the road,
6150	He op'd to all his hospitable gate;
6150	But none of all he entertain'd was there
6150	To ward aside the bitter doom of death:
6150	There fell they both, he and his charioteer,
6150	Calesius, who athwart the battle-field
6150	His chariot drove; one fate o'ertook them both.
6150	""
6150	Then Dresus and Opheltius of their arms
6150	Euryalus despoil'd; his hot pursuit
6150	AEsepus next, and Pedasus assail'd,
6150	Brothers, whom Abarbarea, Naiad nymph,
6150	To bold Bucolion bore; Bucolion, son
6150	Of great Laomedon, his eldest born,
6150	Though bastard: he upon the mountain side,
6150	On which his flocks he tended, met the nymph,
6150	And of their secret loves twin sons were born;
6150	Whom now at once Euryalus of strength
6150	And life depriv'd, and of their armour stripp'd.
6150	""
6150	By Polypoetes' hand, in battle strong,
6150	Was slain Astyalus; Pidutes fell,
6150	Chief of Percote, by Ulysses' spear;
6150	And Teucer godlike Aretaon slew.
6150	Antilochus, the son of Nestor, smote
6150	With gleaming lance Ablerus; Elatus
6150	By Agamemnon, King of men, was slain,
6150	Who dwelt by Satnois' widely-flowing stream,
6150	Upon the lofty heights of Pedasus.
6150	By Leitus was Phylacus in flight
6150	O'erta'en; Eurypylus Melanthius slew.
6150	""
6150	Then Menelaus, good in battle, took
6150	Adrastus captive; for his horses, scar'd
6150	And rushing wildly o'er the plain, amid
6150	The tangled tamarisk scrub his chariot broke,
6150	Snapping the pole; they with the flying crowd
6150	Held city-ward their course; he from the car
6150	Hurl'd headlong, prostrate lay beside the wheel,
6150	Prone on his face in dust; and at his side,
6150	Poising his mighty spear, Atrides stood.
6150	Adrastus clasp'd his knees, and suppliant cried,
6150	"""Spare me, great son of Atreus! for my life"
6150	Accept a price; my wealthy father's house
6150	A goodly store contains of brass, and gold,
6150	And well-wrought iron; and of these he fain
6150	Would pay a noble ransom, could he hear
6150	"That in the Grecian ships I yet surviv'd."""
6150	""
6150	His words to pity mov'd the victor's breast;
6150	Then had he bade his followers to the ships
6150	The captive bear; but running up in haste.
6150	Fierce Agamemnon cried in stern rebuke;
6150	""
6150	"""Soft-hearted Menelaus, why of life"
6150	So tender? Hath thy house receiv'd indeed
6150	Nothing but benefits at Trojan hands?
6150	Of that abhorred race, let not a man
6150	Escape the deadly vengeance of our arms;
6150	No, not the infant in its mother's womb;
6150	No, nor the fugitive; but be they all,
6150	They and their city, utterly destroy'd,
6150	"Uncar'd for, and from mem'ry blotted out."""
6150	""
6150	Thus as he spoke, his counsel, fraught with death,
6150	His brother's purpose chang'd; he with his hand
6150	Adrastus thrust aside, whom with his lance
6150	Fierce Agamemnon through the loins transfix'd;
6150	And, as he roll'd in death, upon his breast
6150	Planting his foot, the ashen spear withdrew.
6150	""
6150	Then loudly Nestor shouted to the Greeks:
6150	"""Friends, Grecian heroes, ministers of Mars!"
6150	Loiter not now behind, to throw yourselves
6150	Upon the prey, and bear it to the ships;
6150	Let all your aim be now to kill; anon
6150	"Ye may at leisure spoil your slaughter'd foes."""
6150	""
6150	With words like these he fir'd the blood of all.
6150	Now had the Trojans by the warlike Greeks
6150	In coward flight within their walls been driv'n;
6150	But to AEneas and to Hector thus
6150	The son of Priam, Helenus, the best
6150	Of all the Trojan seers, address'd his speech:
6150	"""AEneas, and thou Hector, since on you,"
6150	Of all the Trojans and the Lycian hosts,
6150	Is laid the heaviest burthen, for that ye
6150	Excel alike in council and in fight,
6150	Stand here awhile, and moving to and fro
6150	On ev'ry side, around the gates exhort
6150	The troops to rally, lest they fall disgrac'd,
6150	Flying for safety to their women's arms,
6150	And foes, exulting, triumph in their shame.
6150	Their courage thus restor'd, worn as we are,
6150	We with the Greeks will still maintain the fight,
6150	For so, perforce, we must; but, Hector, thou
6150	Haste to the city; there our mother find,
6150	Both thine and mine; on Ilium's topmost height
6150	By all the aged dames accompanied,
6150	Bid her the shrine of blue-ey'd Pallas seek;
6150	Unlock the sacred gates; and on the knees
6150	Of fair-hair'd Pallas place the fairest robe
6150	In all the house, the amplest, best esteem'd;
6150	And at her altar vow to sacrifice
6150	Twelve yearling kine that never felt the goad,
6150	So she have pity on the Trojan state,
6150	Our wives, and helpless babes, and turn away
6150	The fiery son of Tydeus, spearman fierce,
6150	The Minister of Terror; bravest he,
6150	In my esteem, of all the Grecian chiefs:
6150	For not Achilles' self, the prince of men,
6150	Though Goddess-born, such dread inspir'd; so fierce
6150	"His rage; and with his prowess none may vie."""
6150	""
6150	He said, nor uncomplying, Hector heard
6150	His brother's counsel; from his car he leap'd
6150	In arms upon the plain; and brandish'd high
6150	His jav'lins keen, and moving to and fro
6150	The troops encourag'd, and restor'd the fight.
6150	Rallying they turn'd, and fac'd again the Greeks:
6150	These ceas'd from slaughter, and in turn gave way,
6150	Deeming that from the starry Heav'n some God
6150	Had to the rescue come; so fierce they turn'd.
6150	Then to the Trojans Hector call'd aloud:
6150	""
6150	"""Ye valiant Trojans, and renown'd Allies,"
6150	Quit you like men; remember now, brave friends,
6150	Your wonted valour; I to Ilium go
6150	To bid our wives and rev'rend Elders raise
6150	"To Heav'n their pray'rs, with vows of hecatombs."""
6150	""
6150	Thus saying, Hector of the glancing helm
6150	Turn'd to depart; and as he mov'd along,
6150	The black bull's-hide his neck and ancles smote,
6150	The outer circle of his bossy shield.
6150	""
6150	Then Tydeus' son, and Glaucus, in the midst,
6150	Son of Hippolochus, stood forth to fight;
6150	But when they near were met, to Glaucus first
6150	The valiant Diomed his speech address'd:
6150	"""Who art thou, boldest man of mortal birth?"
6150	For in the glorious conflict heretofore
6150	I ne'er have seen thee; but in daring now
6150	Thou far surpassest all, who hast not fear'd
6150	To face my spear; of most unhappy sires
6150	The children they, who my encounter meet.
6150	But if from Heav'n thou com'st, and art indeed
6150	A God, I fight not with the heav'nly powers.
6150	Not long did Dryas' son, Lycurgus brave,
6150	Survive, who dar'd th' Immortals to defy:
6150	He, 'mid their frantic orgies, in the groves
6150	Of lovely Nyssa, put to shameful rout
6150	The youthful Bacchus' nurses; they, in fear,
6150	Dropp'd each her thyrsus, scatter'd by the hand
6150	Of fierce Lycurgus, with an ox-goad arm'd.
6150	Bacchus himself beneath the ocean wave
6150	In terror plung'd, and, trembling, refuge found
6150	In Thetis' bosom from a mortal's threats:
6150	The Gods indignant saw, and Saturn's son
6150	Smote him with blindness; nor surviv'd he long,
6150	Hated alike by all th' immortal Gods.
6150	I dare not then the blessed Gods oppose;
6150	But be thou mortal, and the fruits of earth
6150	"Thy food, approach, and quickly meet thy doom."""
6150	""
6150	To whom the noble Glaucus thus replied:
6150	"""Great son of Tydeus, why my race enquire?"
6150	The race of man is as the race of leaves:
6150	Of leaves, one generation by the wind
6150	Is scattered on the earth; another soon
6150	In spring's luxuriant verdure bursts to light.
6150	So with our race; these flourish, those decay.
6150	But if thou wouldst in truth enquire and learn
6150	The race I spring from, not unknown of men;
6150	There is a city, in the deep recess
6150	Of pastoral Argos, Ephyre by name:
6150	There Sisyphus of old his dwelling had,
6150	Of mortal men the craftiest; Sisyphus,
6150	The son of AEolus; to him was born
6150	Glaucus; and Glaucus in his turn begot
6150	Bellerophon, on whom the Gods bestow'd
6150	The gifts of beauty and of manly grace.
6150	But Proetus sought his death; and, mightier far,
6150	From all the coasts of Argos drove him forth,
6150	To Proetus subjected by Jove's decree.
6150	For him the monarch's wife, Antaea, nurs'd
6150	A madd'ning passion, and to guilty love
6150	Would fain have tempted him; but fail'd to move
6150	The upright soul of chaste Bellerophon.
6150	With lying words she then address'd the King:
6150	'Die, Proetus, thou, or slay Bellerophon,
6150	Who basely sought my honour to assail.'
6150	The King with anger listen'd to her words;
6150	Slay him he would not; that his soul abhorr'd;
6150	But to the father of his wife, the King
6150	Of Lycia, sent him forth, with tokens charg'd
6150	Of dire import, on folded tablets trac'd,
6150	Pois'ning the monarch's mind, to work his death.
6150	To Lycia, guarded by the Gods, he went;
6150	But when he came to Lycia, and the streams
6150	Of Xanthus, there with hospitable rites
6150	The King of wide-spread Lycia welcom'd him.
6150	Nine days he feasted him, nine oxen slew;
6150	But with the tenth return of rosy morn
6150	He question'd him, and for the tokens ask'd
6150	He from his son-in-law, from Proetus, bore.
6150	The tokens' fatal import understood,
6150	He bade him first the dread Chimaera slay;
6150	A monster, sent from Heav'n, not human born,
6150	With head of lion, and a serpent's tail,
6150	And body of a goat; and from her mouth
6150	There issued flames of fiercely-burning fire:
6150	Yet her, confiding in the Gods, he slew.
6150	Next, with the valiant Solymi he fought,
6150	The fiercest fight that e'er he undertook.
6150	Thirdly, the women-warriors he o'erthrew,
6150	The Amazons; from whom returning home,
6150	The King another stratagem devis'd;
6150	For, choosing out the best of Lycia's sons,
6150	He set an ambush; they return'd not home,
6150	For all by brave Bellerophon were slain.
6150	But, by his valour when the King perceiv'd
6150	His heav'nly birth, he entertain'd him well;
6150	Gave him his daughter; and with her the half
6150	Of all his royal honours he bestow'd:
6150	A portion too the Lycians meted out,
6150	Fertile in corn and wine, of all the state
6150	The choicest land, to be his heritage.
6150	Three children there to brave Bellerophon
6150	Were born; Isander, and Hippolochus,
6150	Laodamia last, belov'd of Jove,
6150	The Lord of counsel; and to him she bore
6150	Godlike Sarpedon of the brazen helm.
6150	Bellerophon at length the wrath incurr'd
6150	Of all the Gods; and to th' Aleian plain
6150	Alone he wander'd; there he wore away
6150	His soul, and shunn'd the busy haunts of men.
6150	Insatiate Mars his son Isander slew
6150	In battle with the valiant Solymi:
6150	His daughter perish'd by Diana's wrath.
6150	I from Hippolochus my birth derive:
6150	To Troy he sent me, and enjoin'd me oft
6150	To aim at highest honours, and surpass
6150	My comrades all; nor on my father's name
6150	Discredit bring, who held the foremost place
6150	In Ephyre, and Lycia's wide domain.
6150	"Such is my race, and such the blood I boast."""
6150	""
6150	He said; and Diomed rejoicing heard:
6150	His spear he planted in the fruitful ground,
6150	And thus with friendly words the chief address'd:
6150	""
6150	"""By ancient ties of friendship are we bound;"
6150	For godlike OEneus in his house receiv'd
6150	For twenty days the brave Bellerophon;
6150	They many a gift of friendship interchang'd;
6150	A belt, with crimson glowing, OEneus gave;
6150	Bellerophon a double cup of gold,
6150	Which in my house I left when here I came.
6150	Of Tydeus no remembrance I retain;
6150	For yet a child he left me, when he fell
6150	With his Achaians at the gate of Thebes.
6150	So I in Argos am thy friendly host;
6150	Thou mine in Lycia, when I thither come:
6150	Then shun we, e'en amid the thickest fight,
6150	Each other's lance; enough there are for me
6150	Of Trojans and their brave allies to kill,
6150	As Heav'n may aid me, and my speed of foot;
6150	And Greeks enough there are for thee to slay,
6150	If so indeed thou canst; but let us now
6150	Our armour interchange, that these may know
6150	"What friendly bonds of old our houses join."""
6150	Thus as they spoke, they quitted each his car;
6150	Clasp'd hand in hand, and plighted mutual faith.
6150	Then Glaucus of his judgment Jove depriv'd,
6150	His armour interchanging, gold for brass,
6150	A hundred oxen's worth for that of nine.
6150	""
6150	Meanwhile, when Hector reach'd the oak beside
6150	The Scaean gate, around him throng'd the wives
6150	Of Troy, and daughters, anxious to enquire
6150	The fate of children, brothers, husbands, friends;
6150	He to the Gods exhorted all to pray,
6150	For deep the sorrows that o'er many hung.
6150	But when to Priam's splendid house he came,
6150	With polish'd corridors adorn'd--within
6150	Were fifty chambers, all of polish'd stone,
6150	Plac'd each by other; there the fifty sons
6150	Of Priam with their wedded wives repos'd;
6150	On th' other side, within the court were built
6150	Twelve chambers, near the roof, of polish'd stone,
6150	Plac'd each by other; there the sons-in-law
6150	Of Priam with their spouses chaste repos'd;
6150	To meet him there his tender mother came,
6150	And with her led the young Laodice,
6150	Fairest of all her daughters; clasping then
6150	"His hands, she thus address'd him: ""Why, my son,"
6150	Why com'st thou here, and leav'st the battle-field?
6150	Are Trojans by those hateful sons of Greece,
6150	Fighting around the city, sorely press'd?
6150	And com'st thou, by thy spirit mov'd, to raise,
6150	On Ilium's heights, thy hands in pray'r to Jove?
6150	But tarry till I bring the luscious wine,
6150	That first to Jove, and to th' Immortals all,
6150	Thou mayst thine off'ring pour; then with the draught
6150	Thyself thou mayst refresh; for great the strength
6150	Which gen'rous wine imparts to men who toil,
6150	"As thou hast toil'd, thy comrades to protect."""
6150	""
6150	To whom great Hector of the glancing helm:
6150	"""No, not for me, mine honour'd mother, pour"
6150	The luscious wine, lest thou unnerve my limbs,
6150	And make me all my wonted prowess lose.
6150	The ruddy wine I dare not pour to Jove
6150	With hands unwash'd; nor to the cloud-girt son
6150	Of Saturn may the voice of pray'r ascend
6150	From one with blood bespatter'd and defil'd.
6150	Thou, with the elder women, seek the shrine
6150	Of Pallas; bring your gifts; and on the knees
6150	Of fair-hair'd Pallas place the fairest robe
6150	In all the house, the amplest, best esteem'd;
6150	And at her altar vow to sacrifice
6150	Twelve yearling kine, that never felt the goad;
6150	So she have pity on the Trojan state,
6150	Our wives, and helpless babes; and turn away
6150	The fiery son of Tydeus, spearman fierce,
6150	The Minister of Terror; to the shrine
6150	Of Pallas thou; to Paris I, to call
6150	If haply he will hear; would that the earth
6150	Would gape and swallow him! for great the curse
6150	That Jove thro' him hath brought on men of Troy,
6150	On noble Priam, and on Priam's sons.
6150	Could I but know that he were in his grave,
6150	"Methinks my sorrows I could half forget."""
6150	""
6150	He said: she, to the house returning, sent
6150	Th' attendants through the city, to collect
6150	The train of aged suppliants; she meanwhile
6150	Her fragrant chamber sought, wherein were stor'd
6150	Rich garments by Sidonian women work'd,
6150	Whom godlike Paris had from Sidon brought,
6150	Sailing the broad sea o'er, the selfsame path
