6150	Of these, the richest in embroidery,
6150	The amplest, and the brightest, as a star
6150	Refulgent, plac'd with care beneath the rest,
6150	The Queen her off'ring bore to Pallas' shrine:
6150	She went, and with her many an ancient dame.
6150	But when the shrine they reach'd on Ilium's height,
6150	Theano, fair of face, the gates unlock'd,
6150	Daughter of Cisseus, sage Antenor's wife,
6150	By Trojans nam'd at Pallas' shrine to serve.
6150	They with deep moans to Pallas rais'd their hands;
6150	But fair Theano took the robe, and plac'd
6150	On Pallas' knees, and to the heav'nly Maid,
6150	Daughter of Jove, she thus address'd her pray'r:
6150	"""Guardian of cities, Pallas, awful Queen,"
6150	Goddess of Goddesses, break thou the spear
6150	Of Tydeus' son; and grant that he himself
6150	Prostrate before the Scaean gates may fall;
6150	So at thine altar will we sacrifice
6150	Twelve yearling kine, that never felt the goad,
6150	If thou have pity on the state of Troy,
6150	"The wives of Trojans, and their helpless babes."""
6150	""
6150	Thus she; but Pallas answer'd not her pray'r.
6150	While thus they call'd upon the heav'nly Maid,
6150	Hector to Paris' mansion bent his way;
6150	A noble structure, which himself had built
6150	Aided by all the best artificers
6150	Who in the fertile realm of Troy were known;
6150	With chambers, hall, and court, on Ilium's height,
6150	Near to where Priam's self and Hector dwelt.
6150	There enter'd Hector, well belov'd of Jove;
6150	And in his hand his pond'rous spear he bore,
6150	Twelve cubits long; bright flash'd the weapon's point
6150	Of polish'd brass, with circling hoop of gold.
6150	There in his chamber found he whom he sought,
6150	About his armour busied, polishing
6150	His shield, his breastplate, and his bended bow.
6150	While Argive Helen, 'mid her maidens plac'd,
6150	The skilful labours of their hands o'erlook'd.
6150	To him thus Hector with reproachful words;
6150	"""Thou dost not well thine anger to indulge;"
6150	In battle round the city's lofty wall
6150	The people fast are falling; thou the cause
6150	That fiercely thus around the city burns
6150	The flame of war and battle; and thyself
6150	Wouldst others blame, who from the fight should shrink.
6150	"Up, ere the town be wrapp'd in hostile fires."""
6150	""
6150	To whom in answer godlike Paris thus:
6150	"""Hector, I own not causeless thy rebuke;"
6150	Yet will I speak; hear thou and understand;
6150	'Twas less from anger with the Trojan host,
6150	And fierce resentment, that I here remain'd,
6150	Than that I sought my sorrow to indulge;
6150	Yet hath my wife, e'en now, with soothing words
6150	Urg'd me to join the battle; so, I own,
6150	'Twere best; and Vict'ry changes oft her side.
6150	Then stay, while I my armour don; or thou
6150	"Go first: I, following, will o'ertake thee soon."""
6150	""
6150	He said: but Hector of the glancing helm
6150	Made answer none; then thus with gentle tones
6150	"Helen accosted him: ""Dear brother mine,"
6150	(Of me, degraded, sorrow-bringing, vile!)
6150	Oh that the day my mother gave me birth
6150	Some storm had on the mountains cast me forth!
6150	Or that the many-dashing ocean's waves
6150	Had swept me off, ere all this woe were wrought!
6150	Yet if these evils were of Heav'n ordain'd,
6150	Would that a better man had call'd me wife;
6150	A sounder judge of honour and disgrace:
6150	For he, thou know'st, no firmness hath of mind,
6150	Nor ever will; a want he well may rue.
6150	But come thou in, and rest thee here awhile,
6150	Dear brother, on this couch; for travail sore
6150	Encompasseth thy soul, by me impos'd,
6150	Degraded as I am, and Paris' guilt;
6150	On whom this burthen Heav'n hath laid, that shame
6150	"On both our names through years to come shall rest."""
6150	""
6150	To whom great Hector of the glancing helm:
6150	"""Though kind thy wish, yet, Helen, ask me not"
6150	To sit or rest; I cannot yield to thee:
6150	For to the succour of our friends I haste,
6150	Who feel my loss, and sorely need my aid.
6150	But thou thy husband rouse, and let him speed,
6150	That he may find me still within the walls.
6150	For I too homeward go; to see once more
6150	My household, and my wife, and infant child:
6150	For whether I may e'er again return,
6150	I know not, or if Heav'n have so decreed,
6150	"That I this day by Grecian hands should fall."""
6150	""
6150	Thus saying, Hector of the glancing helm
6150	Turn'd to depart; with rapid step he reach'd
6150	His own well-furnished house, but found not there
6150	His white-arm'd spouse, the fair Andromache.
6150	She with her infant child and maid the while
6150	Was standing, bath'd in tears, in bitter grief,
6150	On Ilium's topmost tower: but when her Lord
6150	Found not within the house his peerless wife,
6150	Upon the threshold pausing, thus he spoke:
6150	"""Tell me, my maidens, tell me true, which way"
6150	Your mistress went, the fair Andromache;
6150	Or to my sisters, or my brothers' wives?
6150	Or to the temple where the fair-hair'd dames
6150	"Of Troy invoke Minerva's awful name?"""
6150	""
6150	To whom the matron of his house replied:
6150	"""Hector, if truly we must answer thee,"
6150	Not to thy sisters, nor thy brothers' wives,
6150	Nor to the temple where the fair-hair'd dames
6150	Of Troy invoke Minerva's awful name,
6150	But to the height of Ilium's topmost tow'r
6150	Andromache is gone; since tidings came
6150	The Trojan force was overmatch'd, and great
6150	The Grecian strength; whereat, like one distract,
6150	She hurried to the walls, and with her took,
6150	"Borne in the nurse's arms, her infant child."""
6150	""
6150	So spoke the ancient dame; and Hector straight
6150	Through the wide streets his rapid steps retrac'd.
6150	But when at last the mighty city's length
6150	Was travers'd, and the Scaean gates were reach'd,
6150	Whence was the outlet to the plain, in haste
6150	Running to meet him came his priceless wife,
6150	Eetion's daughter, fair Andromache;
6150	Eetion, who from Thebes Cilicia sway'd,
6150	Thebes, at the foot of Placos' wooded heights.
6150	His child to Hector of the brazen helm
6150	Was giv'n in marriage: she it was who now
6150	Met him, and by her side the nurse, who bore,
6150	Clasp'd to her breast, his all unconscious child,
6150	Hector's lov'd infant, fair as morning star;
6150	Whom Hector call'd Scamandrius, but the rest
6150	Astyanax, in honour of his sire,
6150	The matchless chief, the only prop of Troy.
6150	Silent he smil'd as on his boy he gaz'd:
6150	But at his side Andromache, in tears,
6150	Hung on his arm, and thus the chief address'd:
6150	""
6150	"""Dear Lord, thy dauntless spirit will work thy doom:"
6150	Nor hast thou pity on this thy helpless child,
6150	Or me forlorn, to be thy widow soon:
6150	For thee will all the Greeks with force combin'd
6150	Assail and slay: for me, 'twere better far,
6150	Of thee bereft, to lie beneath the sod;
6150	Nor comfort shall be mine, if thou be lost,
6150	But endless grief; to me nor sire is left,
6150	Nor honour'd mother; fell Achilles' hand
6150	My sire Eetion slew, what time his arms
6150	The populous city of Cilicia raz'd,
6150	The lofty-gated Thebes; he slew indeed,
6150	But stripp'd him not; he reverenc'd the dead;
6150	And o'er his body, with his armour burnt,
6150	A mound erected; and the mountain nymphs,
6150	The progeny of aegis-bearing Jove,
6150	Planted around his tomb a grove of elms.
6150	There were sev'n brethren in my father's house;
6150	All in one day they fell, amid their herds
6150	And fleecy flocks, by fierce Achilles' hand.
6150	My mother, Queen of Placos' wooded height,
6150	Brought with the captives here, he soon releas'd
6150	For costly ransom; but by Dian's shafts
6150	She, in her father's house, was stricken down.
6150	But, Hector, thou to me art all in one,
6150	Sire, mother, brethren! thou, my wedded love!
6150	Then pitying us, within the tow'r remain,
6150	Nor make thy child an orphan, and thy wife
6150	A hapless widow; by the fig-tree here
6150	Array thy troops; for here the city wall,
6150	Easiest of access, most invites assault.
6150	Thrice have their boldest chiefs this point assail'd,
6150	The two Ajaces, brave Idomeneus,
6150	Th' Atridae both, and Tydeus' warlike son,
6150	Or by the prompting of some Heav'n-taught seer,
6150	"Or by their own advent'rous courage led."""
6150	""
6150	To whom great Hector of the glancing helm;
6150	"""Think not, dear wife, that by such thoughts as these"
6150	My heart has ne'er been wrung; but I should blush
6150	To face the men and long-rob'd dames of Troy,
6150	If, like a coward, I could shun the fight.
6150	Nor could my soul the lessons of my youth
6150	So far forget, whose boast it still has been
6150	In the fore-front of battle to be found,
6150	Charg'd with my father's glory and mine own.
6150	Yet in my inmost soul too well I know,
6150	The day must come when this our sacred Troy,
6150	And Priam's race, and Priam's royal self
6150	Shall in one common ruin be o'erthrown.
6150	But not the thoughts of Troy's impending fate,
6150	Nor Hecuba's nor royal Priam's woes,
6150	Nor loss of brethren, numerous and brave,
6150	By hostile hands laid prostrate in the dust,
6150	So deeply wring my heart as thoughts of thee,
6150	Thy days of freedom lost, and led away
6150	A weeping captive by some brass-clad Greek;
6150	Haply in Argos, at a mistress' beck,
6150	Condemn'd to ply the loom, or water draw
6150	From Hypereia's or Messeis' fount,
6150	Heart-wrung, by stern necessity constrain'd.
6150	Then they who see thy tears perchance may say,
6150	'Lo! this was Hector's wife, who, when they fought
6150	On plains of Troy, was Ilium's bravest chief.'
6150	Thus may they speak; and thus thy grief renew
6150	For loss of him, who might have been thy shield
6150	To rescue thee from slav'ry's bitter hour.
6150	Oh may I sleep in dust, ere be condemn'd
6150	"To hear thy cries, and see thee dragg'd away!"""
6150	""
6150	Thus as he spoke, great Hector stretch'd his arms
6150	To take his child; but back the infant shrank,
6150	Crying, and sought his nurse's shelt'ring breast,
6150	Scar'd by the brazen helm and horse-hair plume,
6150	That nodded, fearful, on the warrior's crest.
6150	Laugh'd the fond parents both, and from his brow
6150	Hector the casque remov'd, and set it down,
6150	All glitt'ring, on the ground; then kiss'd his child,
6150	And danc'd him in his arms; then thus to Jove
6150	And to th' Immortals all address'd his pray'r:
6150	"""Grant, Jove, and all ye Gods, that this my son"
6150	May be, as I, the foremost man of Troy,
6150	For valour fam'd, his country's guardian King;
6150	That men may say, 'This youth surpasses far
6150	His father,' when they see him from the fight,
6150	From slaughter'd foes, with bloody spoils of war
6150	"Returning, to rejoice his mother's heart!"""
6150	""
6150	Thus saying, in his mother's arms he plac'd
6150	His child; she to her fragrant bosom clasp'd,
6150	Smiling through tears; with eyes of pitying love
6150	Hector beheld, and press'd her hand, and thus
6150	"Address'd her--""Dearest, wring not thus my heart!"
6150	For till my day of destiny is come,
6150	No man may take my life; and when it comes,
6150	Nor brave nor coward can escape that day.
6150	But go thou home, and ply thy household cares,
6150	The loom, and distaff, and appoint thy maids
6150	Their sev'ral tasks; and leave to men of Troy
6150	"And, chief of all to me, the toils of war."""
6150	""
6150	Great Hector said, and rais'd his plumed helm;
6150	And homeward, slow, with oft-reverted eyes,
6150	Shedding hot tears, his sorrowing wife return'd.
6150	Arriv'd at valiant Hector's well-built house,
6150	Her maidens press'd around her; and in all
6150	Arose at once the sympathetic grief.
6150	For Hector, yet alive, his household mourn'd,
6150	Deeming he never would again return,
6150	Safe from the fight, by Grecian hands unharm'd.
6150	""
6150	Nor linger'd Paris in his lofty halls;
6150	But donn'd his armour, glitt'ring o'er with brass,
6150	And through the city pass'd with bounding steps.
6150	As some proud steed, at well-fill'd manger fed,
6150	His halter broken, neighing, scours the plain,
6150	And revels in the widely-flowing stream
6150	To bathe his sides; then tossing high his head,
6150	While o'er his shoulders streams his ample mane.
6150	Light borne on active limbs, in conscious pride.
6150	To the wide pastures of the mares he flies;
6150	So Paris, Priam's son, from Ilium's height,
6150	His bright arms flashing like the gorgeous sun,
6150	Hasten'd, with boastful mien, and rapid step.
6150	Hector he found, as from the spot he turn'd
6150	Where with his wife he late had converse held;
6150	Whom thus the godlike Paris first address'd:
6150	"""Too long, good brother, art then here detain'd,"
6150	Impatient for the fight, by my delay;
6150	"Nor have I timely, as thou bad'st me, come."""
6150	To whom thus Hector of the glancing helm:
6150	"""My gallant brother, none who thinks aright"
6150	Can cavil at thy prowess in the field;
6150	For thou art very valiant; but thy will
6150	Is weak and sluggish; and it grieves my heart,
6150	When from the Trojans, who in thy behalf
6150	Such labours undergo, I hear thy name
6150	Coupled with foul reproach! But go we now!
6150	Henceforth shall all be well, if Jove permit
6150	That from our shores we drive th' invading Greeks,
6150	And to the ever-living Gods of Heav'n
6150	"In peaceful homes our free libations pour."""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	ARGUMENT.
6150	""
6150	THE SINGLE COMBAT OF HECTOR AND AJAX.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	The battle renewing with double ardour upon the return of Hector,
6150	Minerva is under apprehensions for the Greeks. Apollo, seeing her
6150	descend from Olympus, joins her near the Scaean gate. They agree to put
6150	off the general engagement for that day, and incite Hector to challenge
6150	the Greeks to a single combat. Nine of the princes accepting the
6150	challenge, the lot is cast, and falls upon Ajax. These heroes, after
6150	several attacks, are parted by the night. The Trojans calling a
6150	council, Antenor proposes the delivery of Helen to the Greeks, to which
6150	Paris will not consent, but offers to restore them her riches. Priam
6150	sends a herald to make this offer, and to demand a truce for burning
6150	the dead, the last of which only is agreed to by Agamemnon. When the
6150	funerals are performed, the Greeks, pursuant to the advice of Nestor,
6150	erect a fortification to protect their fleet and camp, flanked with
6150	towers, and defended by a ditch and palisades. Neptune testifies his
6150	jealousy at this work, but is pacified by a promise from Jupiter. Both
6150	armies pass the night in feasting, but Jupiter disheartens the Trojans
6150	with thunder and other signs of his wrath.
6150	""
6150	The three-and-twentieth day ends with the duel of Hector and Ajax; the
6150	next day the truce is agreed: another is taken up in the funeral rites
6150	of the slain; and one more in building the fortification before the
6150	ships; so that somewhat above three days is employed in this book. The
6150	scene lies wholly in the field.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	BOOK VII.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	Thus as he spoke, from out the city gates
6150	The noble Hector pass'd, and by his side
6150	His brother Paris; in the breast of both
6150	Burnt the fierce ardour of the battle-field.
6150	As when some God a fav'ring breeze bestows
6150	On seamen tugging at the well-worn oar,
6150	Faint with excess of toil, ev'n so appear'd
6150	Those brethren twain to Troy's o'erlabour'd host.
6150	""
6150	Then to their prowess fell, by Paris' hand
6150	Menesthius, royal Areithous' son,
6150	Whom to the King, in Arna, where he dwelt,
6150	The stag-ey'd dame Phylomedusa bore;
6150	While Hector smote, with well-directed spear,
6150	Beneath the brass-bound headpiece, through the throat,
6150	Eioneus, and slack'd his limbs in death;
6150	And Glaucus, leader of the Lycian bands,
6150	Son of Hippolochus, amid the fray
6150	Iphinous, son of Dexias, borne on high
6150	By two fleet mares upon a lofty car,
6150	Pierc'd through the shoulder; from the car he fell
6150	Prone to the earth, his limbs relax'd in death.
6150	But them when Pallas saw, amid the fray
6150	Dealing destruction on the hosts of Greece,
6150	From high Olympus to the walls of Troy
6150	She came in haste; Apollo there she found,
6150	As down he look'd from Ilium's topmost tow'r,
6150	Devising vict'ry to the arms of Troy.
6150	Beside the oak they met; Apollo first,
6150	The son of Jove, the colloquy began:
6150	"""Daughter of Jove, from great Olympus' heights,"
6150	Why com'st thou here, by angry passion led?
6150	Wouldst thou the vict'ry, swaying here and there,
6150	Give to the Greeks? since pitiless thou see'st
6150	The Trojans slaughter'd? Be advis'd by me,
6150	For so 'twere better; cause we for today
6150	The rage of battle and of war to cease;
6150	To-morrow morn shall see the fight renew'd,
6150	Until the close of Ilium's destiny;
6150	For so ye Goddesses have wrought your will,
6150	"That this fair city should in ruin fall."""
6150	""
6150	To whom the blue-ey'd Goddess thus replied:
6150	"""So be it, Archer-King; with like intent"
6150	I from Olympus came; but say, what means
6150	"Wilt thou devise to bid the conflict cease?"""
6150	""
6150	To whom Apollo, royal son of Jove:
6150	"""The might of valiant Hector let us move"
6150	To challenge to the combat, man to man,
6150	Some Grecian warrior; while the brass-clad Greeks
6150	Their champion urge the challenge to accept,
6150	"And godlike Hector meet in single fight."""
6150	""
6150	He said; nor did Minerva not assent;
6150	But Helenus, the son of Priam, knew
6150	The secret counsel by the Gods devis'd;
6150	And drawing near to Hector, thus he spoke:
6150	"""Hector, thou son of Priam, sage as Jove"
6150	In council, hearken to a brother's words.
6150	Bid that the Greeks and Trojans all sit down,
6150	And thou defy the boldest of the Greeks
6150	With thee in single combat to contend;
6150	By revelation from th' eternal Gods,
6150	"I know that here thou shalt not meet thy fate."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and Hector joy'd to hear his words;
6150	Forth in the midst he stepp'd, and with his spear
6150	Grasp'd in the middle, stay'd the Trojan ranks.
6150	With one accord they sat; on th' other side
6150	Atrides bade the well-greav'd Greeks sit down;
6150	While, in the likeness of two vultures, sat
6150	On the tall oak of aegis-bearing Jove,
6150	Pallas, and Phoebus of the silver bow,
6150	With heroes' deeds delighted; dense around
6150	Bristled the ranks, with shield, and helm, and spear.
6150	As when the west wind freshly blows, and brings
6150	A dark'ning ripple o'er the ocean waves,
6150	E'en so appear'd upon the plain the ranks
6150	Of Greeks and Trojans; standing in the midst,
6150	Thus to both armies noble Hector spoke:
6150	"""Hear, all ye Trojans, and ye well-greav'd Greeks,"
6150	The words I speak, the promptings of my soul.
6150	It hath not pleas'd high-thron'd Saturnian Jove
6150	To ratify our truce, who both afflicts
6150	With labours hard, till either ye shall take
6150	Our well-fenc'd city, or yourselves to us
6150	Succumb beside your ocean-going ships.
6150	Here have ye all the chiefest men of Greece;
6150	Of all, let him who dares with me to fight,
6150	Stand forth, and godlike Hector's might confront.
6150	And this I say, and call to witness Jove,
6150	If with the sharp-edg'd spear he vanquish me,
6150	He shall strip off, and to the hollow ships
6150	In triumph bear my armour; but my corpse
6150	Restore, that so the men and wives of Troy
6150	May deck with honours due my funeral pyre.
6150	But, by Apollo's grace should I prevail,
6150	I will his arms strip off and bear to Troy,
6150	And in Apollo's temple hang on high;
6150	But to the ships his corpse I will restore,
6150	That so the long-hair'd Greeks with solemn rites
6150	May bury him, and to his mem'ry raise
6150	By the broad Hellespont a lofty tomb;
6150	And men in days to come shall say, who urge
6150	Their full-oar'd bark across the dark-blue sea,
6150	'Lo there a warrior's tomb of days gone by,
6150	A mighty chief, whom glorious Hector slew:'
6150	"Thus shall they say, and thus my fame shall live."""
6150	""
6150	Thus Hector spoke; they all in silence heard,
6150	Sham'd to refuse, but fearful to accept.
6150	At length in anger Menelaus rose,
6150	Groaning in spirit, and with bitter words
6150	"Reproach'd them: ""Shame, ye braggart cowards, shame!"
6150	Women of Greece! I cannot call you men!
6150	'Twere foul disgrace indeed, and scorn on scorn,
6150	If Hector's challenge none of all the Greeks
6150	Should dare accept; to dust and water turn
6150	All ye who here inglorious, heartless sit!
6150	I will myself confront him; for success,
6150	"Th' immortal Gods above the issues hold."""
6150	""
6150	Thus as he spoke, he donn'd his dazzling arms.
6150	Then, Menelaus, had thine end approach'd
6150	By Hector's hands, so much the stronger he,
6150	Had not the Kings withheld thee and restrain'd.
6150	Great Agamemnon's self, wide-ruling King,
6150	Seizing his hand, address'd him thus by name:
6150	"""What! Heav'n-born Menelaus, art thou mad?"
6150	Beseems thee not such folly; curb thy wrath,
6150	Though vex'd; nor think with Hector to contend,
6150	Thy better far, inspiring dread in all.
6150	From his encounter in the glorious fight,
6150	Superior far to thee, Achilles shrinks;
6150	But thou amid thy comrades' ranks retire;
6150	Some other champion will the Greeks provide;
6150	And, fearless as he is, and of the fight
6150	Insatiate, yet will Hector, should he 'scape
6150	Unwounded from the deadly battle-strife,
6150	"Be fain, methinks, to rest his weary limbs."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and with judicious counsel sway'd
6150	His brother's mind; he yielded to his words,
6150	And gladly his attendants doff'd his arms.
6150	""
6150	Then Nestor rose, and thus address'd the Greeks:
6150	"""Alas, alas! what shame is this for Greece!"
6150	What grief would fill the aged Peleus' soul,
6150	Sage chief in council, of the Myrmidons
6150	Leader approv'd, who often in his house
6150	Would question me, and lov'd from me to hear
6150	Of all the Greeks the race and pedigree,
6150	Could he but learn how Hector cow'd them all!
6150	He to the Gods with hands uprais'd would pray
6150	His soul might from his body be divorc'd,
6150	And sink beneath the earth! Oh would to Jove,
6150	To Pallas and Apollo, such were now
6150	My vig'rous youth, as when beside the banks
6150	Of swiftly-flowing Celadon, the men
6150	Of Pylos with th' Arcadian spearmen fought,
6150	By Pheia's walls, around Iardan's streams.
6150	Then from the ranks, in likeness as a God,
6150	Advanc'd their champion, Ereuthalion bold.
6150	The arms of Areithous he wore:
6150	Of godlike Areithous, whom men
6150	And richly-girdled women had surnam'd
6150	The Macebearer; for not with sword or bow
6150	He went to fight, but with an iron mace
6150	Broke through the squadrons: him Lycurgus slew,
6150	By stealth, not brav'ry, in a narrow way,
6150	Where nought avail'd his iron mace from death
6150	To save him; for Lycurgus, with his spear,
6150	Preventing, thrust him through the midst; he fell
6150	Prostrate; and from his breast the victor stripp'd
6150	His armour off, the gift of brass-clad Mars;
6150	And in the tug of war he wore it oft;
6150	But when Lycurgus felt th' approach of age,
6150	He to his faithful follower and friend,
6150	To Ereuthalion gave it; therewith, arm'd,
6150	He now to combat challeng'd all the chiefs.
6150	None dar'd accept, for fear had fallen on all;
6150	Then I with dauntless spirit his might oppos'd,
6150	The youngest of them all; with him I fought,
6150	And Pallas gave the vict'ry to my arm.
6150	Him there I slew, the tallest, strongest man;
6150	For many another there beside him lay.
6150	Would that my youth and strength were now the same;
6150	Then soon should Hector of the glancing helm
6150	A willing champion find; but ye, of Greece
6150	"The foremost men, with Hector fear to fight."""
6150	""
6150	The old man spoke reproachful; at his words
6150	Up rose nine warriors: far before the rest,
6150	The monarch Agamemnon, King of men;
6150	Next Tydeus' son, the valiant Diomed;
6150	The two Ajaces, cloth'd with courage high;
6150	Idomeneus, and of Idomeneus
6150	The faithful follower, brave Meriones,
6150	Equal in fight to blood-stain'd Mars; with these

6150	Thoas, Andraemon's son; Ulysses last:
6150	These all with Hector offer'd to contend.
6150	Then thus again Gerenian Nestor spoke:
6150	"""Shake then the lots; on whomsoe'er it fall,"
6150	Great profit shall he bring to Grecian arms,
6150	Great glory to himself, if he escape
6150	"Unwounded from the deadly battle strife."""
6150	He said: each mark'd his sev'ral lot, and all
6150	Together threw in Agamemnon's helm.
6150	The crowd, with hands uplifted, pray'd the Gods,
6150	"And looking heav'nward, said, ""Grant, Father Jove,"
6150	The lot on Ajax, or on Tydeus' son,
6150	"Or on Mycenae's wealthy King may fall."""
6150	""
6150	Thus they: then aged Nestor shook the helm,
6150	And forth, according to their wish, was thrown
6150	The lot of Ajax; then from left to right
6150	A herald show'd to all the chiefs of Greece,
6150	In turn, the token; they who knew it not,
6150	Disclaim'd it all; but when to him he came
6150	Who mark'd, and threw it in Atrides' helm,
6150	The noble Ajax, and, approaching, placed
6150	The token in his outstretch'd hand, forthwith
6150	He knew it, and rejoic'd; before his feet
6150	He threw it down upon the ground, and said,
6150	"""O friends, the lot is mine; great is my joy,"
6150	And hope o'er godlike Hector to prevail.
6150	But now, while I my warlike armour don,
6150	Pray ye to Saturn's royal son, apart,
6150	In silence, that the Trojans hear ye not;
6150	Or ev'n aloud, for nought have we to fear.
6150	No man against my will can make me fly,
6150	By greater force or skill; nor will, I hope,
6150	My inexperience in the field disgrace
6150	"The teaching of my native Salamis."""
6150	""
6150	Thus he; and they to Saturn's royal son
6150	Address'd their pray'rs, and looking heav'nward, said:
6150	"""O Father Jove, who rul'st on Ida's height!"
6150	Most great!  most glorious!  grant that Ajax now
6150	May gain the vict'ry, and immortal praise:
6150	Or if thy love and pity Hector claim,
6150	"Give equal pow'r and equal praise to both."""
6150	""
6150	Ajax meanwhile in dazzling brass was clad;
6150	And when his armour all was duly donn'd,
6150	Forward he mov'd, as when gigantic Mars
6150	Leads nations forth to war, whom Saturn's son
6150	In life-destroying conflict hath involv'd;
6150	So mov'd the giant Ajax, prop of Greece,
6150	With sternly smiling mien; with haughty stride
6150	He trod the plain, and pois'd his pond'rous spear.
6150	The Greeks, rejoicing, on their champion gaz'd,
6150	The Trojans' limbs beneath them shook with fear;
6150	Ev'n Hector's heart beat quicker in his breast;
6150	Yet quail he must not now, nor back retreat
6150	Amid his comrades--he, the challenger!
6150	Ajax approach'd; before him, as a tow'r
6150	His mighty shield he bore, sev'n-fold, brass-bound,
6150	The work of Tychius, best artificer
6150	That wrought in leather; he in Hyla dwelt.
6150	Of sev'n-fold hides the pond'rous shield was wrought
6150	Of lusty bulls; the eighth was glitt'ring brass.
6150	This by the son of Telamon was borne
6150	Before his breast; to Hector close he came,
6150	And thus with words of haughty menace spoke:
6150	""
6150	"""Hector, I now shall teach thee, man to man,"
6150	The mettle of the chiefs we yet possess,
6150	Although Achilles of the lion heart,
6150	Mighty in battle, be not with us still;
6150	He by his ocean-going ships indeed
6150	Against Atrides nurses still his wrath;
6150	Yet are there those who dare encounter thee,
6150	"And not a few; then now begin the fight."""
6150	""
6150	To whom great Hector of the glancing helm:
6150	"""Ajax, brave leader, son of Telamon,"
6150	Deal not with me as with a feeble child,
6150	Or woman, ign'rant of the ways of war;
6150	Of war and carnage every point I know;
6150	And well I know to wield, now right, now left,
6150	The tough bull's-hide that forms my stubborn targe:
6150	Well know I too my fiery steeds to urge,
6150	And raise the war-cry in the standing fight.
6150	But not in secret ambush would I watch,
6150	To strike, by stealth, a noble foe like thee;
6150	"But slay thee, if I may, in open fight."""
6150	""
6150	He said; and, poising, hurl'd his pond'rous spear;
6150	The brazen cov'ring of the shield it struck,
6150	The outward fold, the eighth, above the sev'n
6150	Of tough bull's-hide; through six it drove its way
6150	With stubborn force; but in the seventh was stay'd,
6150	Then Ajax hurl'd in turn his pond'rous spear,
6150	And struck the circle true of Hector's shield;
6150	Right thro' the glitt'ring shield the stout spear pass'd,
6150	And thro' the well-wrought breastplate drove its way;
6150	And, underneath, the linen vest it tore;
6150	But Hector, stooping, shunn'd the stroke of death.
6150	Withdrawing then their weapons, each on each
6150	They fell, like lions fierce, or tusked boars,
6150	In strength the mightiest of the forest beasts.
6150	Then Hector fairly on the centre struck
6150	The stubborn shield; yet drove not through the spear;
6150	For the stout brass the blunted point repell'd.
6150	But Ajax, with a forward bound, the shield
6150	Of Hector pierc'd; right through the weapon pass'd;
6150	Arrested with rude shock the warrior's course,
6150	And graz'd his neck, that spouted forth the blood.
6150	Yet did not Hector of the glancing helm
6150	Flinch from the contest: stooping to the ground,
6150	With his broad hand a pond'rous stone he seiz'd,
6150	That lay upon the plain, dark, jagg'd, and huge,
6150	And hurl'd against the sev'n-fold shield, and struck
6150	Full on the central boss; loud rang the brass:
6150	Then Ajax rais'd a weightier mass of rock
6150	And sent it whirling, giving to his arm
6150	Unmeasur'd impulse; with a millstone's weight
6150	It crush'd the buckler; Hector's knees gave way;
6150	Backward he stagger'd, yet upon his shield
6150	Sustain'd, till Phoebus rais'd him to his feet.
6150	Now had they hand to hand with swords engag'd,
6150	Had not the messengers of Gods and men,
6150	The heralds, interpos'd; the one for Troy,
6150	The other umpire for the brass-clad Greeks,
6150	Talthybius and Idaeus, well approv'd.
6150	Between the chiefs they held their wands, and thus
6150	Idaeus both with prudent speech address'd:
6150	"""No more, brave youths! no longer wage the fight:"
6150	To cloud-compelling Jove ye both are dear,
6150	Both valiant spearmen; that, we all have seen.
6150	"Night is at hand; behoves us yield to night."""
6150	""
6150	Whom answer'd thus the son of Telamon:
6150	"""Idaeus, bid that Hector speak those words:"
6150	He challeng'd all our chiefs; let him begin:
6150	"If he be willing, I shall not refuse."""
6150	""
6150	To whom great Hector of the glancing helm:
6150	"""Ajax, since God hath giv'n thee size, and strength,"
6150	And skill; and with the spear, of all the Greeks
6150	None is thine equal; cease we for to-day
6150	The fight; hereafter we may meet, and Heav'n
6150	Decide our cause, and one with vict'ry crown.
6150	Night is at hand; behoves us yield to night.
6150	So by the ships shalt thou rejoice the Greeks,
6150	And most of all, thy comrades and thy friends;
6150	And so shall I, in Priam's royal town,
6150	Rejoice the men of Troy, and long-rob'd dames,
6150	Who shall with grateful pray'rs the temples throng.
6150	But make we now an interchange of gifts,
6150	That both the Trojans and the Greeks may say,
6150	'On mortal quarrel did those warriors meet,
6150	"Yet parted thence in friendly bonds conjoin'd.'"""
6150	""
6150	This said, a silver-studded sword he gave,
6150	With scabbard and with well-cut belt complete;
6150	Ajax a girdle, rich with crimson dye.
6150	They parted; Ajax to the Grecian camp,
6150	And Hector to the ranks of Troy return'd:
6150	Great was the joy when him they saw approach,
6150	Alive and safe; escap'd from Ajax' might
6150	And arm invincible; and tow'rd the town
6150	They led him back, beyond their hope preserv'd;
6150	While to Atrides' tent the well-greav'd Greeks
6150	Led Ajax, glorying in his triumph gain'd.
6150	""
6150	But when to Agamemnon's tents they came,
6150	The King of men to Saturn's royal son
6150	A bullock slew, a male of five years old;
6150	The carcase then they flay'd; and cutting up,
6150	Sever'd the joints; then fixing on the spits,
6150	Roasted with care, and from the fire withdrew.
6150	Their labours ended, and the feast prepar'd,
6150	They shar'd the social meal, nor lack'd there aught.
6150	To Ajax then the chine's continuous length,
6150	As honour's meed, the mighty monarch gave.
6150	The rage of thirst and hunger satisfied,
6150	The aged Nestor first his mind disclos'd;
6150	He who, before, the sagest counsel gave,
6150	Now thus with prudent speech began, and said:
6150	"""Atrides, and ye other chiefs of Greece,"
6150	Since many a long-hair'd Greek hath fall'n in fight,
6150	Whose blood, beside Scamander's flowing stream,
6150	Fierce Mars has shed, while to the viewless shades
6150	Their spirits are gone, behoves thee with the morn
6150	The warfare of the Greeks to intermit:
6150	Then we, with oxen and with mules, the dead
6150	From all the plain will draw; and, from the ships
6150	A little space remov'd, will burn with fire:
6150	That we, returning to our native land,
6150	May to their children bear our comrades' bones.
6150	Then will we go, and on the plain erect
6150	Around the pyre one common mound for all;
6150	Then quickly build before it lofty tow'rs
6150	To screen both ships and men; and in the tow'rs
6150	Make ample portals, with well-fitting gates,
6150	That through the midst a carriage-way may pass:
6150	And a deep trench around it dig, to guard
6150	Both men and chariots, lest on our defence
6150	"The haughty Trojans should too hardly press."""
6150	""
6150	He said; and all the Kings his words approv'd.
6150	Meanwhile, on Ilium's height, at Priam's gate
6150	The Trojan chiefs a troubled council held;
6150	Which op'ning, thus the sage Antenor spoke:
6150	"""Hear now, ye Trojans, Dardans, and Allies,"
6150	The words I speak, the promptings of my soul.
6150	Back to the sons of Atreus let us give
6150	The Argive Helen, and the goods she brought;
6150	For now in breach of plighted faith we fight:
6150	Nor can I hope, unless to my advice
6150	"Ye listen, that success will crown our arms."""
6150	Thus having said, he sat; and next arose
6150	The godlike Paris, fair-hair'd Helen's Lord;
6150	Who thus with winged words the chiefs address'd:
6150	"""Hostile to me, Antenor, is thy speech;"
6150	Thy better judgment better counsel knows;
6150	But if in earnest such is thine advice,
6150	Thee of thy senses have the Gods bereft.
6150	Now, Trojans, hear my answer; I reject
6150	The counsel, nor the woman will restore;
6150	But for the goods, whate'er I hither brought
6150	To Troy from Argos, I am well content
6150	"To give them all, and others add beside."""
6150	""
6150	This said, he sat; and aged Priam next,
6150	A God in council, Dardan's son, arose,
6150	Who thus with prudent speech began, and said:
6150	""
6150	"""Hear now, ye Trojans, Dardans, and Allies,"
6150	The words I speak, the promptings of my soul:
6150	Now through the city take your wonted meal;
6150	Look to your watch, let each man keep his guard:
6150	To-morrow shall Idaeus to the ships
6150	Of Greece, to both the sons of Atreus, bear
6150	The words of Paris, cause of all this war;
6150	And ask besides, if from the deadly strife
6150	Such truce they will accord us as may serve
6150	To burn the dead; hereafter we may fight
6150	"Till Heav'n decide, and one with vict'ry crown."""
6150	""
6150	He said; and they, obedient to his word,
6150	Throughout the ranks prepar'd the wonted meal:
6150	But with the morning to the ships of Greece
6150	Idaeus took his way: in council there
6150	By Agamemnon's leading ship he found
6150	The Grecian chiefs, the ministers of Mars:
6150	And 'mid them all the clear-voic'd herald spoke:
6150	""
6150	"""Ye sons of Atreus, and ye chiefs of Greece,"
6150	From Priam, and the gallant sons of Troy,
6150	I come, to bear, if ye be pleas'd to hear,
6150	The words of Paris, cause of all this war:
6150	The goods which hither in his hollow ships
6150	(Would he had perish'd rather!) Paris brought,
6150	He will restore, and others add beside;
6150	But further says, the virgin-wedded wife
6150	Of Menelaus, though the gen'ral voice
6150	Of Troy should bid him. he will not restore:
6150	Then bids me ask, if from the deadly strife
6150	Such truce ye will accord us as may serve
6150	To burn the dead: hereafter we may fight
6150	"Till Heav'n decide, and one with vict'ry crown."""
6150	""
6150	Thus he: they all in silence heard; at length
6150	Uprose the valiant Diomed, and said;
6150	"""Let none from Paris now propose to accept"
6150	Or goods, or Helen's self; a child may see
6150	"That now the doom of Troy is close at hand."""
6150	He said; the sons of Greece, with loud applause,
6150	The speech of valiant Diomed confirm'd.
6150	""
6150	Then to Idaeus Agamemnon thus:
6150	"""Idaeus, thou hast heard what answer give"
6150	The chiefs of Greece--their answer I approve.
6150	But for the truce, for burial of the dead,
6150	I nought demur; no shame it is to grace
6150	With fun'ral rites the corpse of slaughter'd foes.
6150	"Be witness, Jove! and guard the plighted truce."""
6150	""
6150	He said: and heav'nward rais'd his staff; and back
6150	To Ilium's walls Idaeus took his way.
6150	Trojans and Dardans there in council met
6150	Expecting sat, till from the Grecian camp
6150	Idaeus should return; he came, and stood
6150	In mid assembly, and his message gave:
6150	Then all in haste their sev'ral ways dispers'd,
6150	For fuel some, and some to bring the dead.
6150	The Greeks too from their well-mann'd ships went forth,
6150	For fuel some, and some to bring the dead.
6150	The sun was newly glancing on the earth.
6150	From out the ocean's smoothly-flowing depths
6150	Climbing the Heav'ns, when on the plain they met.
6150	Hard was it then to recognize the dead;
6150	But when the gory dust was wash'd away,
6150	Shedding hot tears, they plac'd them on the wains.
6150	Nor loud lament, by Priam's high command,
6150	Was heard; in silence they, with grief suppress'd,
6150	Heap'd up their dead upon the fun'ral pyre;
6150	Then burnt with fire, and back return'd to Troy.
6150	The well-greav'd Greeks, they too, with grief suppress'd,
6150	Heap'd up their dead upon the fun'ral pyre;
6150	Then burnt with fire, and to the ships return'd.
6150	""
6150	But ere 'twas morn, while daylight strove with night,
6150	About the pyre a chosen band of Greeks
6150	Had kept their vigil, and around it rais'd
6150	Upon the plain one common mound for all;
6150	And built in front a wall, with lofty tow'rs
6150	To screen both ships and men; and in the tow'rs
6150	Made ample portals with well-fitting gates,
6150	That through the midst a carriage-way might pass:
6150	Then dug a trench around it, deep and wide,
6150	And in the trench a palisade they fix'd.
6150	""
6150	Thus labour'd thro' the night the long-hair'd Greeks:
6150	The Gods, assembled in the courts of Jove,
6150	With wonder view'd the mighty work; and thus
6150	Neptune, Earth-shaking King, his speech began:
6150	"""O Father Jove, in all the wide-spread earth"
6150	Shall men be found, in counsel and design
6150	To rival us Immortals? see'st thou not
6150	How round their ships the long-hair'd Greeks have built
6150	A lofty wall, and dug a trench around,
6150	Nor to the Gods have paid their off'rings due!
6150	Wide as the light extends shall be the fame
6150	Of this great work, and men shall lightly deem
6150	Of that which I and Phoebus jointly rais'd,
6150	"With toil and pain, for great Laomedon."""
6150	""
6150	To whom in wrath the Cloud-compeller thus:
6150	"""Neptune, Earth-shaking King, what words are these?"
6150	This bold design to others of the Gods,
6150	Of feebler hands, and pow'r less great than thine,
6150	Might cause alarm; but, far as light extends,
6150	Of this great work to thee shall be the fame:
6150	When with their ships the long-hair'd Greeks shall take
6150	Their homeward voyage to their native land,
6150	This wall shall by the waves be broken through,
6150	And sink, a shapeless ruin, in the sea:
6150	O'er the wide shore again thy sands shall spread,
6150	"And all the boasted work of Greece o'erwhelm."""
6150	""
6150	Amid themselves such converse held the Gods.
6150	The sun was set; the Grecian work was done;
6150	They slew, and shar'd, by tents, the ev'ning meal.
6150	From Lemnos' isle a num'rous fleet had come
6150	Freighted with wine; and by Euneus sent,
6150	Whom fair Hypsipyle to Jason bore.
6150	For Atreus' sons, apart from all the rest,
6150	Of wine, the son of Jason had despatch'd
6150	A thousand measures; all the other Greeks
6150	Hasten'd to purchase, some with brass, and some
6150	With gleaming iron; other some with hides,
6150	Cattle, or slaves; and joyous wax'd the feast.
6150	All night the long-hair'd Greeks their revels held,
6150	And so in Troy, the Trojans and Allies:
6150	But through the night his anger Jove express'd
6150	With awful thunderings; pale they turn'd with fear:
6150	To earth the wine was from the goblets shed,
6150	Nor dar'd they drink, until libations due
6150	Had first been pour'd to Saturn's mighty son.
6150	""
6150	Then lay they down, and sought the boon of sleep.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	ARGUMENT.
6150	""
6150	THE SECOND BATTLE, AND THE DISTRESS OF THE GREEKS.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	Jupiter assembles a council of the deities, and threatens them with the
6150	pains of Tartarus, if they assist either side: Minerva only obtains of
6150	him that she may direct the Greeks by her counsels. The armies join
6150	battle; Jupiter on Mount Ida weighs in his balances the fates of both,
6150	and affrights the Greeks with his thunders and lightnings. Nestor alone
6150	continues in the field in great danger; Diomed relieves him; whose
6150	exploits, and those of Hector, are excellently described. Juno
6150	endeavours to animate Neptune to the assistance of the Greeks, but in
6150	vain. The acts of Teucer, who is at length wounded by Hector, and
6150	carried off. Juno and Minerva prepare to aid the Grecians, but are
6150	restrained by Iris, sent from Jupiter. The night puts an end to the
6150	battle. Hector continues in the field, (the Greeks being driven to
6150	their fortifications before the ships,) and gives orders to keep the
6150	watch all night in the camp, to prevent the enemy from re-embarking and
6150	escaping by flight. They kindle fires through all the field, and pass
6150	the night under arms.
6150	""
6150	The time of seven-and-twenty days is employed from the opening of the
6150	poem to the end of this book. The scene here (except of the celestial
6150	machines) lies in the field toward the sea-shore.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	BOOK VIII.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	Now morn, in saffron robe, the earth o'erspread;
6150	And Jove, the lightning's Lord, of all the Gods
6150	A council held upon the highest peak
6150	Of many-ridg'd Olympus; he himself
6150	Address'd them; they his speech attentive heard.
6150	""
6150	"""Hear, all ye Gods, and all ye Goddesses,"
6150	The words I speak, the promptings of my soul.
6150	Let none among you, male or female, dare
6150	To thwart my counsels: rather all concur,
6150	That so these matters I may soon conclude.
6150	If, from the rest apart, one God I find
6150	Presuming or to Trojans or to Greeks
6150	To give his aid, with ignominious stripes
6150	Back to Olympus shall that God be driv'n;
6150	Or to the gloom of Tartarus profound,
6150	Far off, the lowest abyss beneath the earth,
6150	With, gates of iron, and with floor of brass,
6150	Beneath the shades as far as earth from Heav'n,
6150	There will I hurl him, and ye all shall know
6150	In strength how greatly I surpass you all.
6150	Make trial if ye will, that all may know.
6150	A golden cord let down from Heav'n, and all,
6150	Both Gods and Goddesses, your strength, apply:
6150	Yet would ye fail to drag from Heav'n to earth,
6150	Strive as ye may, your mighty master, Jove;
6150	But if I choose to make my pow'r be known,
6150	The earth itself, and ocean, I could raise,
6150	And binding round Olympus' ridge the cord,
6150	Leave them suspended so in middle air:
6150	"So far supreme my pow'r o'er Gods and men."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and they, confounded by his words,
6150	In silence sat; so sternly did he speak.
6150	At length the blue-ey'd Goddess, Pallas, said:
6150	"""O Father, Son of Saturn, King of Kings,"
6150	Well do we know thy pow'r invincible;
6150	Yet deeply grieve we for the warlike Greeks,
6150	Condemn'd to hopeless ruin; from the fight,
6150	Since such is thy command, we stand aloof;
6150	But yet some saving counsel may we give,
6150	"Lest in thine anger thou destroy them quite."""
6150	""
6150	To whom the Cloud-compeller, smiling, thus:
6150	"""Be of good cheer, my child; unwillingly"
6150	"I speak, yet will not thwart thee of thy wish."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and straight the brazen-footed steeds,
6150	Of swiftest flight, with manes of flowing gold,
6150	He harness'd to his chariot; all in gold
6150	Himself array'd, the golden lash he grasp'd,
6150	Of curious work; and mounting on his car,
6150	Urg'd the fleet coursers; nothing loth, they flew
6150	Midway betwixt the earth and starry heav'n.
6150	To Ida's spring-abounding hill he came,
6150	And to the crest of Gargarus, wild nurse
6150	Of mountain beasts; a sacred plot was there,
6150	Whereon his incense-honour'd altar stood:
6150	There stay'd his steeds the Sire of Gods and men
6150	Loos'd from the car, and veil'd with clouds around.
6150	Then on the topmost ridge he sat, in pride
6150	Of conscious strength; and looking down, survey'd
6150	The Trojan city, and the ships of Greece.
6150	""
6150	Meantime, the Greeks throughout their tents in haste
6150	Despatch'd their meal, and arm'd them for the fight;
6150	On th' other side the Trojans donn'd their arms,
6150	In numbers fewer, but with stern resolve,
6150	By hard necessity constrain'd, to strive,
6150	For wives and children, in the stubborn fight.
6150	The gates all open'd wide, forth pour'd the crowd
6150	Of horse and foot; and loud the clamour rose.
6150	When in the midst they met, together rush'd
6150	Bucklers and lances, and the furious might
6150	Of mail-clad warriors; bossy shield on shield
6150	Clatter'd in conflict; loud the clamour rose:
6150	Then rose too mingled shouts and groans of men
6150	Slaying and slain; the earth ran red with blood.
6150	While yet 'twas morn, and wax'd the youthful day,
6150	Thick flew the shafts, and fast the people fell
6150	On either side; but when the sun had reach'd
6150	The middle Heav'n, th' Eternal Father hung
6150	His golden scales aloft, and plac'd in each
6150	The fatal death-lot: for the sons of Troy
6150	The one, the other for the brass-clad Greeks;
6150	Then held them by the midst; down sank the lot
6150	Of Greece, down to the ground, while high aloft
6150	Mounted the Trojan scale, and rose to Heav'n.                   [2]
6150	Then loud he bade the volleying thunder peal
6150	From Ida's heights; and 'mid the Grecian ranks
6150	He hurl'd his flashing lightning; at the sight
6150	Amaz'd they stood, and pale with terror shook.
6150	""
6150	Then not Idomeneus, nor Atreus' son,
6150	The mighty Agamemnon, kept their ground,
6150	Nor either Ajax, ministers of Mars;
6150	Gerenian Nestor, aged prop of Greece,
6150	Alone remain'd, and he against his will,
6150	His horse sore wounded by an arrow shot
6150	By godlike Paris, fair-hair'd Helen's Lord:
6150	Just on the crown, where close behind the head
6150	First springs the mane, the deadliest spot of all,
6150	The arrow struck him; madden'd with the pain
6150	He rear'd, then plunging forward, with the shaft
6150	Fix'd in his brain, and rolling in the dust,
6150	The other steeds in dire confusion threw;
6150	And while old Nestor with his sword essay'd
6150	To cut the reins, and free the struggling horse,
6150	Amid the rout down came the flying steeds
6150	Of Hector, guided by no timid hand,
6150	By Hector's self; then had the old man paid
6150	The forfeit of his life, but, good at need,
6150	The valiant Diomed his peril saw,
6150	And loudly shouting, on Ulysses call'd:
6150	"""Ulysses sage, Laertes' godlike son,"
6150	Why fliest thou, coward-like, amid the throng,
6150	And in thy flight to the aim of hostile spears
6150	Thy back presenting? stay, and here with me
6150	"From this fierce warrior guard the good old man."""
6150	""
6150	He said; but stout Ulysses heard him not,
6150	And to the ships pursued his hurried way.
6150	But in the front, Tydides, though alone,
6150	Remain'd undaunted; by old Nester's car
6150	He stood, and thus the aged chief address'd:
6150	"""Old man, these youthful warriors press thee sore,"
6150	Thy vigour spent, and with the weight of years
6150	Oppress'd; and helpless too thy charioteer,
6150	And slow thy horses; mount my car, and prove
6150	How swift my steeds, or in pursuit or flight,
6150	From those of Tros descended, scour the plain;
6150	My noble prize from great AEneas won.
6150	Leave to th' attendants these; while mine we launch
6150	Against the Trojan host, that Hector's self
6150	"May know how strong my hand can hurl the spear."""
6150	""
6150	He said; and Nestor his advice obey'd:
6150	The two attendants, valiant Sthenelus,
6150	And good Eurymedon, his horses took,
6150	While on Tydides' car they mounted both.
6150	The aged Nestor took the glitt'ring reins,
6150	And urg'd the horses; Hector soon they met:
6150	As on he came, his spear Tydides threw,
6150	Yet struck not Hector; but his charioteer,
6150	Who held the reins, the brave Thebaeus' son,
6150	Eniopeus, through the breast transfix'd,
6150	Beside the nipple; from the car he fell,
6150	The startled horses swerving at the sound;
6150	And from his limbs the vital spirit fled.
6150	Deep, for his comrade slain, was Hector's grief;
6150	Yet him, though griev'd, perforce he left to seek
6150	A charioteer; nor wanted long his steeds
6150	A guiding hand; for Archeptolemus,
6150	Brave son of Iphitus, he quickly found,
6150	And bade him mount his swiftly-flying car,
6150	And to his hands the glitt'ring reins transferr'd.
6150	""
6150	Then fearful ruin had been wrought, and deeds
6150	Untold achiev'd, and like a flock of lambs,
6150	The adverse hosts been coop'd beneath the walls,
6150	Had not the Sire of Gods and men beheld,
6150	And with an awful peal of thunder hurl'd
6150	His vivid lightning down; the fiery bolt
6150	Before Tydides' chariot plough'd the ground.
6150	Fierce flash'd the sulph'rous flame, and whirling round
6150	Beneath the yoke th' affrighted horses quailed.
6150	""
6150	From Nestor's hand escap'd the glitt'ring reins,
6150	And, trembling, thus to Diomed he spoke:
6150	""
6150	"""Turn we to flight, Tydides; see'st thou not,"
6150	That Jove from us his aiding hand withholds?
6150	This day to Hector Saturn's son decrees
6150	The meed of vict'ry; on some future day,
6150	If so he will, the triumph may be ours;
6150	For man, how brave soe'er, cannot o'errule
6150	"The will of Jove, so much the mightier he."""
6150	""
6150	Whom answer'd thus the valiant Diomed:
6150	"""Truly, old man, and wisely dost thou speak;"
6150	But this the bitter grief that wrings my soul:
6150	Some day, amid the councillors of Troy
6150	Hector may say, 'Before my presence scar'd
6150	Tydides sought the shelter of the ships.'
6150	"Thus when he boasts, gape earth, and hide my shame!"""
6150	""
6150	To whom Gerenian Nestor thus replied:
6150	"""Great son of Tydeus, oh what words are these!"
6150	Should Hector brand thee with a coward's name,
6150	No credence would he gain from Trojan men,
6150	Or Dardan, or from Trojan warriors' wives,
6150	"Whose husbands in the dust thy hand hath laid."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and 'mid the general rout, to flight
6150	He turn'd his horses; on the flying crowd,
6150	With shouts of triumph, Hector at their head,
6150	The men of Troy their murd'rous weapons show'r'd.
6150	Loud shouted Hector of the glancing helm:
6150	"""Tydides, heretofore the warrior Greeks"
6150	Have held thee in much honour; plac'd on high
6150	At banquets, and with lib'ral portions grac'd,
6150	And flowing cups: but thou, from this day forth,
6150	Shalt be their scorn! a woman's soul is thine!
6150	Out on thee, frighten'd girl! thou ne'er shalt scale
6150	Our Trojan tow'rs, and see me basely fly;
6150	Nor in thy ships our women bear away:
6150	"Ere such thy boast, my hand shall work thy doom."""
6150	""
6150	Thus he; and greatly was Tydides mov'd
6150	To turn his horses, and confront his foe:
6150	Thrice thus he doubted; thrice, at Jove's command,
6150	From Ida's height the thunder peal'd, in sign
6150	Of vict'ry swaying to the Trojan side.
6150	Then to the Trojans Hector call'd aloud:
6150	"""Trojans, and Lycians, and ye Dardans, fam'd"
6150	In close encounter, quit ye now like men;
6150	Put forth your wonted valour; for I know
6150	That in his secret counsels Jove designs
6150	Glory to me, disaster to the Greeks.
6150	Fools, in those wretched walls that put their trust,
6150	Scarce worthy notice, hopeless to withstand
6150	My onset; and the trench that they have dug,
6150	Our horses easily can overleap;
6150	And when I reach the ships, be mindful ye,
6150	To have at hand the fire, wherewith the ships
6150	We may destroy, while they themselves shall fall
6150	"An easy prey, bewilder'd by the smoke."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and thus with cheering words address'd
6150	"His horses: ""Xanthus, and, Podargus, thou,"
6150	AEthon and Lampus, now repay the care
6150	On you bestow'd by fair Andromache,
6150	Eetion's royal daughter; bear in mind
6150	How she with ample store of provender
6150	Your mangers still supplied, before e'en I,
6150	Her husband, from her hands the wine-cup took.
6150	Put forth your speed, that we may make our prize
6150	Of Nestor's shield, whose praise extends to Heav'n,
6150	Its handles, and itself, of solid gold;
6150	And from the shoulders of Tydides strip
6150	His gorgeous breastplate, work of Vulcan's hand:
6150	These could we take, methinks this very night
6150	"Would see the Greeks embarking on their ships."""
6150	""
6150	Such was his pray'r; but Juno on her throne
6150	Trembled with rage, till great Olympus quak'd,
6150	And thus to Neptune, mighty God, she spoke:
6150	"""O thou of boundless might, Earth-shaking God,"
6150	See'st thou unmov'd the ruin of the Greeks?
6150	Yet they in AEgae and in Helice,
6150	With grateful off'rings rich thine altars crown;
6150	Then give we them the vict'ry; if we all
6150	Who favour Greece, together should combine
6150	To put to flight the Trojans, and restrain
6150	All-seeing Jove, he might be left alone,
6150	"On Ida's summit to digest his wrath."""
6150	""
6150	To whom, in anger, Neptune thus replied:
6150	"""O Juno, rash of speech, what words are these!"
6150	I dare not counsel that we all should join
6150	"'Gainst Saturn's son; so much the stronger he."""
6150	""
6150	Such converse held they; all the space meanwhile
6150	Within the trench, between the tow'r and ships,
6150	Was closely throng'd with steeds and buckler'd men;
6150	By noble Hector, brave as Mars, and led
6150	By Jove to vict'ry, coop'd in narrow space;
6150	Who now had burnt with fire the Grecian ships,
6150	But Juno bade Atrides haste to rouse
6150	Their fainting courage; through the camp he pass'd;
6150	On his broad hand a purple robe he bore,
6150	And stood upon Ulysses' lofty ship,
6150	The midmost, whence to shout to either side,
6150	Or to the tents of Ajax Telamon,
6150	Or of Achilles, who at each extreme,
6150	Confiding in their strength, had moor'd their ships.
6150	""
6150	Thence to the Greeks he shouted, loud and clear:
6150	"""Shame on ye, Greeks, base cowards, brave alone"
6150	In outward semblance! where are now the vaunts
6150	Which once (so highly of ourselves we deem'd)
6150	Ye made, vain-glorious braggarts as ye were,
6150	In Lemnos' isle, when, feasting on the flesh
6150	Of straight-horn'd oxen, and your flowing cups
6150	Crowning with ruddy wine, not one of you,
6150	But for a hundred Trojans in the field,
6150	Or for two hundred, deem'd himself a match:
6150	Now quail ye all before a single man,
6150	Hector, who soon will wrap our ships in fire.
6150	O Father Jove! what sov'reign e'er hast thou
6150	So far deluded, of such glory robb'd?
6150	Yet ne'er, on this disastrous voyage bent,
6150	Have I unheeded pass'd thine altar by;
6150	The choicest off'rings burning still on each,
6150	In hopes to raze the well-built walls of Troy.
6150	Yet to this pray'r at least thine ear incline;
6150	Grant that this coast in safety we may leave,
6150	"Nor be by Trojans utterly subdued."""
6150	""
6150	He said; and Jove, with pity, saw his tears;
6150	And, with a sign, his people's safety vouch'd.
6150	He sent an eagle, noblest bird that flies,
6150	Who in his talons bore a wild deer's fawn:
6150	The fawn he dropp'd beside the holy shrine,
6150	Where to the Lord of divination, Jove,
6150	The Greeks were wont their solemn rites to pay.
6150	The sign from Heav'n they knew; with courage fresh
6150	Assail'd the Trojans, and the fight renew'd.
6150	Then none of all the many Greeks might boast
6150	That he, before Tydides, drove his car
6150	Across the ditch, and mingled in the fight.
6150	His was the hand that first a crested chief,
6150	The son of Phradmon, Agelaus, struck.
6150	He turn'd his car for flight; but as he turn'd,
6150	The lance of Diomed, behind his neck,
6150	Between the shoulders, through his chest was driv'n;
6150	Headlong he fell, and loud his armour rang.
6150	""
6150	Next to Tydides, Agamemnon came,
6150	And Menelaus, Atreus' godlike sons;
6150	Th' Ajaces both, in dauntless courage cloth'd;
6150	Idomeneus, with whom Meriones,
6150	His faithful comrade, terrible as Mars;
6150	Eurypylus, Euaemon's noble son;
6150	The ninth was Teucer, who, with bended bow,
6150	Behind the shield of Ajax Telamon
6150	Took shelter; Ajax o'er him held his shield;
6150	Thence look'd he round, and aim'd amid the crowd;
6150	And as he saw each Trojan, wounded, fall,
6150	Struck by his shafts, to Ajax close he press'd,
6150	As to its mother's shelt'ring arms a child,
6150	Conceal'd and safe beneath the ample targe.
6150	""
6150	Say then, who first of all the Trojans fell
6150	By Teucer's arrows slain? Orsilochus,
6150	And Ophelestes, Daetor, Ormenus,
6150	And godlike Lycophontes, Chromius,
6150	And Amopaon, Polyaemon's son,
6150	And valiant Melanippus: all of these,
6150	Each after other, Teucer laid in dust.
6150	Him Agamemnon, with his well-strung bow
6150	Thinning the Trojan ranks, with joy beheld,
6150	And, standing at his side, address'd him thus:
6150	"""Teucer, good comrade, son of Telamon,"
6150	Shoot ever thus, if thou wouldst be the light
6150	And glory of the Greeks, and of thy sire,
6150	Who nursed thine infancy, and in his house
6150	Maintain'd, though bastard; him, though distant far,
6150	To highest fame let thine achievements raise.
6150	This too I say, and will make good my word:
6150	If by the grace of aegis-bearing Jove,
6150	And Pallas, Ilium's well-built walls we raze,
6150	A gift of honour, second but to mine,
6150	I in thy hands will place; a tripod bright,
6150	Or, with their car and harness, two brave steeds,
6150	"Or a fair woman who thy bed may share."""
6150	""
6150	To whom in answer valiant Teucer thus:
6150	"""Most mighty son of Atreus, why excite"
6150	Who lacks not zeal? To th' utmost of my pow'r
6150	Since first we drove the Trojans back, I watch,
6150	Unceasing, every chance to ply my shafts.
6150	Eight barbed arrows have I shot e'en now,
6150	And in a warrior each has found its mark;
6150	"That savage hound alone defeats my aim."""
6150	""
6150	At Hector, as he spoke, another shaft
6150	He shot, ambitious of so great a prize:
6150	He miss'd his aim; but Priam's noble son
6150	Gorgythion, through the breast his arrow struck,
6150	Whom from AEsyme brought, a wedded bride
6150	Of heavenly beauty, Castianeira bore.
6150	Down sank his head, as in a garden sinks
6150	A ripen'd poppy charg'd with vernal rains;
6150	So sank his head beneath his helmet's weight.
6150	At Hector yet another arrow shot
6150	Teucer, ambitious of so great a prize;
6150	Yet this too miss'd, by Phoebus turn'd aside;
6150	But Archeptolemus, the charioteer
6150	Of Hector, onward hurrying, through the breast
6150	It struck, beside the nipple; from the car
6150	He fell; aside the startled horses swerv'd;
6150	And as he fell the vital spirit fled.
6150	Deep, for his comrade slain, was Hector's grief;
6150	Yet him, though griev'd at heart, perforce he left,
6150	And to Cebriones, his brother, call'd,
6150	Then near at hand, the horses' reins to take;
6150	He heard, and straight obey'd; then Hector leap'd
6150	Down from his glitt'ring chariot to the ground,
6150	His fearful war-cry shouting; in his hand
6150	A pond'rous stone he carried; and, intent
6150	To strike him down, at Teucer straight he rush'd.
6150	He from his quiver chose a shaft in haste,
6150	And fitted to the cord; but as he drew
6150	The sinew, Hector of the glancing helm
6150	Hurl'd the huge mass of rock, which Teucer struck
6150	Near to the shoulder, where the collar-bone
6150	Joins neck and breast, the spot most opportune,
6150	And broke the tendon; paralys'd, his arm
6150	Dropp'd helpless by his side; upon his knees
6150	He fell, and from his hands let fall the bow.
6150	Not careless Ajax saw his brother's fall,
6150	But o'er him spread in haste his cov'ring shield.
6150	Two faithful friends, Mecisteus, Echius' son,
6150	And brave Alastor, from the press withdrew,
6150	And bore him, deeply groaning, to the ships.
6150	""
6150	Then Jove again the Trojan courage fir'd,
6150	And backward to the ditch they forc'd the Greeks.
6150	Proud of his prowess, Hector led them on;
6150	And as a hound that, fleet of foot, o'ertakes
6150	Or boar or lion, object of his chase,
6150	Springs from behind, and fastens on his flank,
6150	Yet careful watches, lest he turn to bay:
6150	So Hector press'd upon the long-hair'd Greeks,
6150	Slaying the hindmost; they in terror fled.
6150	But, pass'd at length the ditch and palisade,
6150	With loss of many by the Trojans slain,
6150	Before the ships they rallied from their flight,
6150	And one to other call'd: and one and all
6150	With hands uplifted, pray'd to all the Gods;
6150	While Hector, here and there, on ev'ry side
6150	His flying coursers wheel'd, with eyes that flash'd
6150	Awful as Gorgon's, or as blood-stain'd Mars.
6150	""
6150	Juno, the white-arm'd Queen, with pity mov'd,
6150	To Pallas thus her winged words address'd:
6150	"""O Heav'n, brave child of aegis-bearing Jove,"
6150	Can we, ev'n now, in this their sorest need,
6150	Refuse the Greeks our aid, by one subdued,
6150	One single man, of pride unbearable,
6150	Hector, the son of Priam, who e'en now,
6150	"Hath caus'd them endless grief?"" To whom again"
6150	The blue-ey'd Goddess, Pallas, thus replied:
6150	"""I too would fain behold him robb'd of life,"
6150	In his own country slain by Grecian hands;
6150	But that my sire, by ill advice misled,
6150	Rages in wrath, still thwarting all my plans;
6150	Forgetting now how oft his son I sav'd,
6150	Sore wearied with the toils Eurystheus gave.
6150	Oft would his tears ascend to Heav'n, and oft
6150	From Heav'n would Jove despatch me to his aid;
6150	But if I then had known what now I know,
6150	When to the narrow gates of Pluto's realm
6150	He sent him forth to bring from Erebus
6150	Its guardian dog, he never had return'd
6150	In safety from the marge of Styx profound.
6150	He holds me now in hatred, and his ear
6150	To Thetis lends, who kiss'd his knees, and touch'd
6150	His beard, and pray'd him to avenge her son
6150	Achilles; yet the time shall come when I
6150	Shall be once more his own dear blue-ey'd Maid.
6150	But haste thee now, prepare for us thy car,
6150	While to the house of aegis-bearing Jove
6150	I go, and don my armour for the fight,
6150	To prove if Hector of the glancing helm,
6150	The son of Priam, will unmov'd behold
6150	Us two advancing o'er the pass of war;
6150	Or if the flesh of Trojans, slain by Greeks,
6150	"Shall sate the maw of rav'ning dogs and birds."""
6150	""
6150	She said: the white-arm'd Queen her word obey'd.
6150	Juno, great Goddess, royal Saturn's child,
6150	The horses brought, with golden frontlets crown'd;
6150	While Pallas, child of aegis-bearing Jove,
6150	Within her father's threshold dropp'd her veil
6150	Of airy texture, work of her own hands;
6150	The cuirass donn'd of cloud-compelling Jove,
6150	And stood accoutred for the bloody fray.
6150	The fiery car she mounted; in her hand
6150	A spear she bore, long, weighty, tough; wherewith
6150	The mighty daughter of a mighty sire
6150	Sweeps down the ranks of those her wrath pursues.
6150	Then Juno sharply touch'd the flying steeds;
6150	Forthwith spontaneous opening, grated harsh
6150	The heavenly portals, guarded by the Hours,
6150	Who Heav'n and high Olympus have in charge,
6150	To roll aside or close the veil of cloud;
6150	Through these th' excited horses held their way.
6150	""
6150	From Ida's heights the son of Saturn saw,
6150	And, fill'd with wrath, the heav'nly messenger,
6150	The golden-winged Iris, thus bespoke:
6150	"""Haste thee, swift Iris; turn them back, and warn"
6150	That farther they advance not: 'tis not meet
6150	That they and I in war should be oppos'd.
6150	This too I say, and will make good my words:
6150	Their flying horses I will lame; themselves
6150	Dash from their car, and break their chariot-wheels;
6150	And ten revolving years heal not the wound
6150	Where strikes my lightning; so shall Pallas learn
6150	What 'tis against her father to contend.
6150	Juno less moves my wonder and my wrath;
6150	"Whate'er I plan, 'tis still her wont to thwart."""
6150	Thus he: from Ida to Olympus' height
6150	The storm-swift Iris on her errand sped.
6150	At many-ridg'd Olympus' outer gate
6150	She met the Goddesses, and stay'd their course,
6150	And thus convey'd the sov'reign will of Jove:
6150	""
6150	"""Whither away? what madness fills your breasts?"
6150	To give the Greeks your succour, Jove forbids;
6150	And thus he threatens, and will make it good:
6150	Your flying horses he will lame; yourselves
6150	Dash from the car, and break your chariot-wheels;
6150	And ten revolving years heal not the wounds
6150	His lightning makes: so, Pallas, shalt thou learn
6150	What 'tis against thy father to contend.
6150	Juno less moves his wonder and his wrath;
6150	Whate'er he plans, 'tis still her wont to thwart;
6150	But over-bold and void of shame art thou,
6150	"If against Jove thou dare to lift thy spear."""
6150	""
6150	Thus as she spoke, swift Iris disappear'd.
6150	"Then Juno thus to Pallas spoke: ""No more,"
6150	Daughter of aegis-bearing Jove, can we
6150	For mortal men his sov'reign will resist;
6150	Live they or die, as each man's fate may be;
6150	While he, 'twixt Greeks and Trojans, as 'tis meet,
6150	"His own designs accomplishing, decides."""
6150	She said, and backward turn'd her horses' heads.
6150	The horses from the car the Hours unyok'd,
6150	And safely tether'd in the heav'nly stalls;
6150	The car they rear'd against the inner wall,
6150	That brightly polish'd shone; the Goddesses
6150	Themselves meanwhile, amid th' Immortals all,
6150	With, sorrowing hearts on golden seats reclin'd.
6150	""
6150	Ere long, on swiftly-rolling chariot borne,
6150	Jove to Olympus, to th' abode of Gods,
6150	From Ida's height return'd: th' earth-shaking God,
6150	Neptune, unyok'd his steeds; and on the stand
6150	Secur'd the car, and spread the cov'ring o'er.
6150	Then on his golden throne all-seeing Jove
6150	Sat down; beneath his feet Olympus shook.
6150	Juno and Pallas only sat aloof;
6150	No word they utter'd, no enquiry made.
6150	Jove knew their thoughts, and thus address'd them both:
6150	"""Pallas and Juno, wherefore sit ye thus"
6150	In angry silence? In the glorious fight
6150	No lengthen'd toil have ye sustain'd, to slay
6150	The Trojans, whom your deadly hate pursues.
6150	Not all the Gods that on Olympus dwell
6150	Could turn me from my purpose, such my might,
6150	And such the pow'r of my resistless hand;
6150	But ye were struck with terror ere ye saw
6150	The battle-field, and fearful deeds of war.
6150	But this I say, and bear it in your minds,
6150	Had I my lightning launch'd, and from your car
6150	Had hurl'd ye down, ye ne'er had reach'd again
6150	"Olympus' height, th' immortal Gods' abode."""
6150	""
6150	So spoke the God; but, seated side by side,
6150	Juno and Pallas glances interchang'd
6150	Of ill portent for Troy; Pallas indeed
6150	Sat silent, and, though inly wroth with Jove,
6150	Yet answer'd not a word; but Juno's breast
6150	Could not contain her rage, and thus she spoke:
6150	"""What words, dread son of Saturn, dost thou speak?"
6150	Well do we know thy pow'r invincible,
6150	Yet deeply grieve we for the warlike Greeks,
6150	Condemn'd to hopeless ruin: from the fight,
6150	Since such is thy command, we stand aloof;
6150	But yet some saving counsel may we give,
6150	"Lest in thine anger thou destroy them quite."""
6150	""
6150	To whom the Cloud-compeller thus replied:
6150	"""Yet greater slaughter, stag-ey'd Queen of Heav'n,"
6150	To-morrow shalt thou see, if so thou list,
6150	Wrought on the warrior Greeks by Saturn's son;
6150	For Hector's proud career shall not be check'd
6150	Until the wrath of Peleus' godlike son
6150	Beside the ships be kindled, in the day
6150	When round Patroclus' corpse, in narrow space,
6150	E'en by the vessels' sterns, the war shall rage.
6150	Such is the voice of destiny: for thee,
6150	I reck not of thy wrath; nor should I care
6150	Though thou wert thrust beneath the lowest deep
6150	Of earth and ocean, where Iapetus
6150	And Saturn lie, uncheer'd by ray of sun
6150	Or breath of air, in Tartarus profound.
6150	Though there thou wert to banishment consign'd,
6150	I should not heed, but thy reproaches hear
6150	"Unmov'd; for viler thing is none than thou."""
6150	He said, but white-arm'd Juno answer'd not.
6150	""
6150	The sun, now sunk beneath the ocean wave,
6150	Drew o'er the teeming earth the veil of night.
6150	The Trojans saw, reluctant, day's decline;
6150	But on the Greeks thrice welcome, thrice invoked
6150	With earnest prayers, the shades of darkness fell.
6150	""
6150	The noble Hector then to council call'd
6150	The Trojan leaders; from the ships apart
6150	He led them, by the eddying river's side,
6150	To a clear space of ground, from corpses free.
6150	They from their cars dismounting, to the words
6150	Of godlike Hector listen'd: in his hand
6150	His massive spear he held, twelve cubits long,
6150	Whose glitt'ring point flash'd bright, with hoop of gold
6150	Encircled round; on this he leant, and said,
6150	"""Hear me, ye Trojans, Dardans, and Allies;"
6150	I hop'd that to the breezy heights of Troy
6150	We might ere now in triumph have return'd,
6150	The Grecian ships and all the Greeks destroy'd:
6150	But night hath come too soon, and sav'd awhile
6150	The Grecian army and their stranded ships.
6150	Then yield we to the night; prepare the meal;
6150	Unyoke your horses, and before them place
6150	Their needful forage; from the city bring
6150	Oxen and sheep; the luscious wine provide;
6150	Bring bread from out our houses; and collect
6150	Good store of fuel, that the livelong night,
6150	E'en till the dawn of day, may broadly blaze
6150	Our num'rous watchfires, and illume the Heav'ns;
6150	Lest, e'en by night, the long-hair'd Greeks should seek
6150	O'er the broad bosom of the sea to fly,
6150	That so not unassail'd they may embark,
6150	Nor undisturb'd; but haply some may bear,
6150	E'en to their homes, the mem'ry of a wound
6150	Receiv'd from spear or arrow, as on board
6150	They leap'd in haste; and others too may fear
6150	To tempt with hostile arms the pow'r of Troy.
6150	Then let the sacred heralds' voice proclaim
6150	Throughout the city, that the stripling youths
6150	And hoary-headed sires allot themselves
6150	In sev'ral watches to the Heav'n-built tow'rs.
6150	Charge too the women, in their houses each,
6150	To kindle blazing fires; let careful watch
6150	Be set, lest, in the absence of the men,
6150	The town by secret ambush be surpris'd.
6150	Such, valiant Trojans, is th' advice I give;
6150	And what to-night your wisdom shall approve
6150	Will I, at morn, before the Trojans speak.
6150	Hopeful, to Jove I pray, and all the Gods,
6150	To chase from hence these fate-inflicted hounds,
6150	By fate sent hither on their dark-ribb'd ships.
6150	Now keep we through the night our watchful guard;
6150	And with the early dawn, equipp'd in arms,
6150	Upon their fleet our angry battle pour.
6150	Then shall I know if Tydeus' valiant son
6150	Back from the ships shall drive me to the walls,
6150	Or I, triumphant, bear his bloody spoils:
6150	To-morrow morn his courage will decide,
6150	If he indeed my onset will await.
6150	But ere to-morrow's sun be high in Heav'n,
6150	He, 'mid the foremost, if I augur right,
6150	Wounded and bleeding in the dust shall lie,
6150	And many a comrade round him. Would to Heav'n
6150	I were as sure to be from age and death
6150	Exempt, and held in honour as a God,
6150	Phoebus, or Pallas, as I am assur'd
6150	"The coming day is fraught with ill to Greece."""
6150	""
6150	Thus Hector spoke; the Trojans shouted loud:
6150	Then from the yoke the sweating steeds they loos'd,
6150	And tether'd each beside their sev'ral cars:
6150	Next from the city speedily they brought
6150	Oxen and sheep; the luscious wine procur'd;
6150	Brought bread from out their houses, and good store
6150	Of fuel gather'd; wafted from the plain,
6150	The winds to Heav'n the sav'ry odours bore.
6150	Full of proud hopes, upon the pass of war,
6150	All night they camp'd; and frequent blaz'd their fires.
6150	""
6150	As when in Heav'n, around the glitt'ring moon
6150	The stars shine bright amid the breathless air;
6150	And ev'ry crag, and ev'ry jutting peak
6150	Stands boldly forth, and ev'ry forest glade;
6150	Ev'n to the gates of Heav'n is open'd wide
6150	The boundless sky; shines each particular star
6150	Distinct; joy fills the gazing shepherd's heart.
6150	So bright, so thickly scatter'd o'er the plain,
6150	Before the walls of Troy, between the ships
6150	And Xanthus' stream, the Trojan watchfires blaz'd.
6150	""
6150	A thousand fires burnt brightly; and round each
6150	Sat fifty warriors in the ruddy glare;
6150	Champing the provender before them laid,
6150	Barley and rye, the tether'd horses stood
6150	Beside the cars, and waited for the morn.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	ARGUMENT.
6150	""
6150	THE EMBASSY TO ACHILLES.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	Agamemnon, after the last day's defeat, proposes to the Greeks to quit
6150	the siege, and return to their country. Diomed opposes this, and Nestor
6150	seconds him, praising his wisdom and resolution. He orders the guard to
6150	be strengthened, and a council summoned to deliberate what meabures
6150	were to be followed in this emergency. Agamemnon pursues this advice,
6150	and Nestor farther prevails upon him to send ambassadors to Achilles in
6150	order to move him to a reconciliation. Ulysses and Ajax are make choice
6150	of, who are accompanied by old Phoenix. They make, each of them, very
6150	moving and pressing speeches, but are rejected with roughness by
6150	Achilles, who notwithstanding retains Phoenix in his tent. The
6150	ambassadors return unsuccessfully to the camp, and the troops betake
6150	themselves to sleep.
6150	""
6150	This book, and the next following, take up the space of one night,
6150	which is the twenty-seventh from the beginning of the poem. The scene
6150	lies on the sea-shore, the station of the Grecian ships.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	BOOK IX.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	Thus kept their watch, the Trojans; but the Greeks
6150	Dire Panic held, companion of chill Fear,
6150	Their bravest struck with grief unbearable.
6150	As when two stormy winds ruffle the sea,
6150	Boreas and Zephyr, from the hills of Thrace
6150	With sudden gust descending; the dark waves
6150	Rear high their angry crests, and toss on shore
6150	Masses of tangled weed; such stormy grief
6150	The breast of ev'ry Grecian warrior rent.
6150	""
6150	Atrides, heart-struck, wander'd to and fro,
6150	And to the clear-voic'd heralds gave command
6150	To call, but not with proclamation loud,
6150	Each sev'ral man to council; he himself
6150	Spar'd not his labour, mixing with the chiefs.
6150	Sadly they sat in council; Atreus' son,
6150	Weeping, arose; as some dark-water'd fount
6150	Pours o'er a craggy steep its gloomy stream;
6150	Then with deep groans th' assembled Greeks address'd:
6150	"""O friends! the chiefs and councillors of Greece,"
6150	Grievous, and all unlook'd for, is the blow
6150	Which Jove hath dealt me; by his promise led
6150	I hop'd to raze the strong-built walls of Troy,
6150	And home return in safety; but it seems
6150	He falsifies his word, and bids me now
6150	Return to Argos, frustrate of my hope,
6150	Dishonour'd, and with grievous loss of men.
6150	Such now appears th' o'er-ruling sov'reign will
6150	Of Saturn's son, who oft hath sunk the heads
6150	Of many a lofty city in the dust,
6150	And yet will sink; for mighty is his hand.
6150	Hear then my counsel; let us all agree
6150	Home to direct our course: since here in vain
6150	"We strive to take the well-built walls of Troy."""
6150	""
6150	The monarch spoke; they all in silence heard:
6150	In speechless sorrow long they sat: at length
6150	Rose valiant Diomed, and thus he spoke:
6150	"""Atrides, I thy folly must confront,"
6150	As is my right, in council: thou, O King!
6150	Be not offended: once, among the Greeks
6150	Thou heldest light my prowess, with the name
6150	Of coward branding me; how justly so
6150	Is known to all the Greeks, both young and old.
6150	On thee the deep-designing Saturn's son
6150	In diff'ring measure hath his gifts bestow'd:
6150	A throne he gives thee, higher far than all;
6150	But valour, noblest boon of Heav'n, denies.
6150	How canst thou hope the sons of Greece shall prove
6150	Such heartless dastards as thy words suppose?
6150	If homeward to return thy mind be fix'd,
6150	Depart; the way is open, and the ships,
6150	Which from Mycenae follow'd thee in crowds,
6150	Are close at hand, and ready to be launch'd.
6150	Yet will the other long-hair'd Greeks remain
6150	Till Priam's city fall: nay, though the rest
6150	Betake them to their ships, and sail for home,
6150	Yet I and Sthenelus, we two, will fight
6150	"Till Troy be ours; for Heav'n is on our side."""
6150	""
6150	Thus he; the sons of Greece, with loud applause,
6150	The speech of valiant Diomed confirm'd.
6150	""
6150	Then aged Nestor rose, and thus began:
6150	"""Tydides, eminent thou art in war;"
6150	And In the council thy compeers in age
6150	Must yield to thee; thy present words, no Greek
6150	Can censure, or gainsay; and yet the end
6150	Thou hast not reach'd, and object of debate.
6150	But thou art young, and for thine age mightst be
6150	My latest born; yet dost thou to the Kings
6150	Sage counsel give, and well in season speak.
6150	But now will I, that am thine elder far,
6150	Go fully through the whole; and none my words
6150	May disregard, not ev'n Atrides' self.
6150	Outcast from kindred, law, and hearth is he
6150	Whose soul delights in fierce internal strife.
6150	But yield we now to th' influence of night:
6150	Prepare the meal; and let the sev'ral guards
