6150	Be posted by the ditch, without the wall.
6150	This duty on the younger men I lay:
6150	Then, Agamemnon, thou thy part perform;
6150	For thou art King supreme; the Elders all,
6150	As meet and seemly, to the feast invite:
6150	Thy tents are full of wine, which Grecian ships
6150	O'er the wide sea bring day by day from Thrace;
6150	Nor lack'st thou aught thy guests to entertain,
6150	And many own thy sway; when all are met,
6150	His counsel take, who gives the best advice;
6150	Great need we have of counsel wise and good,
6150	When close beside our ships the hostile fires
6150	Are burning: who can this unmov'd behold?
6150	"This night our ruin or our safety sees."""
6150	""
6150	He said; and they, assenting, heard his speech.
6150	Forth with their followers went th' appointed guards,
6150	The princely Thrasymedes, Nestor's son,
6150	Ascalaphus, and bold Ialmenus,
6150	Two valiant sons of Mars; Meriones,
6150	And Aphareus, and brave Deipyrus,
6150	And godlike Lycomedes, Creon's son.
6150	Sev'n were the leaders; and with each went forth
6150	A hundred gallant youths, with lances arm'd.
6150	Between the ditch and wall they took their post;
6150	There lit their fires, and there the meal prepar'd.
6150	""
6150	Then for th' assembled Elders in his tent
6150	An ample banquet Agamemnon spread;
6150	They on the viands, set before them, fell:
6150	The rage of thirst and hunger satisfied,
6150	The aged Nestor first his mind disclos'd
6150	He who, before, the sagest counsel gave,
6150	Now thus with prudent words began, and said:
6150	""
6150	"""Most mighty Agamemnon, King of men,"
6150	With thee, Atrides, my discourse shall end,
6150	With thee begin: o'er many nations thou
6150	Hold'st sov'reign sway; since Jove to thee hath giv'n
6150	The sceptre, and the high prerogative,
6150	To be thy people's judge and counsellor,
6150	'Tis thine to speak the word, 'tis thine to hear
6150	And to determine, when some other chief
6150	Suggestions offers in the gen'ral cause:
6150	What counsel shall prevail, depends on thee:
6150	Yet will I say what seems to me the best.
6150	Sounder opinion none can hold than this,
6150	Which I maintain, and ever have maintain'd,
6150	Ev'n from the day when thou, great King, didst bear
6150	The fair Briseis from Achilles' tent
6150	Despite his anger--not by my advice:
6150	I fain would have dissuaded thee, but thou,
6150	Following the dictates of thy wrathful pride,
6150	Didst to our bravest wrong, dishon'ring him
6150	Whom ev'n th' Immortals honour'd; for his prize
6150	Thou took'st and still retain'st; but let us now
6150	Consider, if ev'n yet, with costly gifts
6150	"And soothing words, we may his wrath appease."""
6150	""
6150	To whom the monarch Agamemnon thus:
6150	"""Father, too truly thou recall'st my fault:"
6150	I err'd, nor will deny it; as a host
6150	Is he whom Jove in honour holds, as now
6150	Achilles hon'ring, he confounds the Greeks,
6150	But if I err'd, by evil impulse led,
6150	Fain would I now conciliate him, and pay
6150	An ample penalty; before you all
6150	I pledge myself rich presents to bestow.
6150	Sev'n tripods will I give, untouch'd by fire;
6150	Of gold, ten talents, twenty caldrons bright,
6150	Twelve pow'rful horses, on the course renown'd,
6150	Who by their speed have many prizes won.
6150	Not empty-handed could that man be deem'd,
6150	Nor poor in gold, who but so much possess'd
6150	As by those horses has for me been won.
6150	Sev'n women too, well skill'd in household cares,
6150	Lesbians, whom I selected for myself,
6150	That day he captur'd Lesbos' goodly isle,
6150	In beauty far surpassing all their sex:
6150	These will I give; and with them will I send
6150	The fair Briseis, her whom from his tent
6150	I bore away; and add a solemn oath,
6150	I ne'er approach'd her bed, nor held with her
6150	Such intercourse as man with woman holds.
6150	All these shall now be his: but if the Gods
6150	Shall grant us Priam's city to destroy,
6150	Of gold and brass, when we divide the spoil,
6150	With countless heaps he shall a vessel freight,
6150	And twenty captives he himself shall choose,
6150	All only less than Argive Helen fair.
6150	And if it be our fate to see again
6150	The teeming soil of Argos, he shall be
6150	My son by marriage; and in honour held
6150	As is Orestes, who, my only son,
6150	Is rear'd at home in luxury and ease.
6150	Three daughters fair I have, Chrysothemis,
6150	Iphianassa, and Laodice;
6150	Of these, whiche'er he will, to Peleus' house,
6150	No portion ask'd for, he shall take to wife;
6150	And with her will I add such wedding gifts,
6150	As never man before to daughter gave.
6150	Sev'n prosp'rous towns besides; Cardamyle,
6150	And Enope, and Ira's grassy plains;
6150	And Pherae, and Antheia's pastures deep,
6150	AEpeia fair, and vine-clad Pedasus;
6150	All by the sea, by sandy Pylos' bounds.
6150	The dwellers there in flocks and herds are rich,
6150	And, as a God, shall honour him with gifts,
6150	And to his sceptre ample tribute pay.
6150	This will I do, so he his wrath remit:
6150	Then let him yield (Pluto alone remains
6150	Unbending and inexorable; and thence
6150	Of all the Gods is most abhorr'd of men),
6150	To me submitting, as in royal pow'r
6150	"Superior far, and more advanc'd in age."""
6150	""
6150	To whom Gerenian Nestor thus replied:
6150	"""Most mighty Agamemnon, King of men,"
6150	Atrides, not unworthy are the gifts,
6150	Which to Achilles thou design'st to send:
6150	Then to the tent of Peleus' son in haste
6150	Let us our chosen messengers despatch:
6150	Whom I shall choose, let them consent to go.
6150	Then first of all let Phoenix lead the way,
6150	Beloved of Jove; the mighty Ajax next:
6150	With them, Ulysses sage; and let them take,
6150	Of heralds, Hodius and Eurybates.
6150	Bring now the hallowing water for our hands;
6150	And bid be silent, while to Saturn's son,
6150	"That he have mercy, we address our pray'r."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and well his counsel pleas'd them all;
6150	The heralds pour'd the water on their hands;
6150	The youths, attending, crown'd the bowls with wine,
6150	And in due order serv'd the cups to all.
6150	Then, their libations made, when each with wine
6150	Had satisfied his soul, from out the tent
6150	Of Agamemnon, Atreus' son, they pass'd;
6150	And many a caution aged Nestor gave,
6150	With rapid glance to each, Ulysses chief,
6150	How best to soften Peleus' matchless son.
6150	""
6150	Beside the many-dashing ocean's shore
6150	They mov'd along; and many a pray'r address'd
6150	To Neptune, Ocean's Earth-surrounding God,
6150	That he to gentle counsels would incline
6150	The haughty soul of great AEacides.
6150	When to the ships and tents they came, where lay
6150	The warlike Myrmidons, their chief they found
6150	His spirit soothing with a sweet-ton'd lyre,
6150	Of curious work, with silver band adorn'd;
6150	Part of the spoil he took, when he destroy'd
6150	Eetion's wealthy town; on this he play'd,
6150	Soothing his soul, and sang of warriors' deeds.
6150	Before the chief, in silence and alone
6150	Patroclus sat, upon Achilles fix'd
6150	His eyes, awaiting till the song should cease.
6150	The envoys forward stepp'd, Ulysses first,
6150	And stood before him; from his couch, amaz'd,
6150	And holding still his lyre, Achilles sprang,
6150	Leaving the seat whereon they found him plac'd;
6150	And at their entrance rose Patroclus too:
6150	Waving his hand, Achilles, swift of foot,
6150	"Addressed them: ""Welcome, friends! as friends ye come:"
6150	Some great occasion surely to my tent
6150	Hath brought the men who are, of all the Greeks,
6150	"Despite my anger, dearest to my heart."""
6150	""
6150	Thus as he spoke, he led them in, and plac'd
6150	On couches spread with, purple carpets o'er,
6150	Then thus address'd Patroclus at his side:
6150	"""Son of Menoetius, set upon the board"
6150	A larger bowl, and stronger mix the wine,
6150	And serve a cup to each: beneath my roof
6150	"This night my dearest friends I entertain."""
6150	He said; Patroclus his commands obey'd;
6150	And in the fire-light plac'd an ample tray,
6150	And on it laid of goat's flesh and of sheep's
6150	A saddle each; and with them, rich in fat,
6150	A chine of well-fed hog; Automedon
6150	Held fast, while great Achilles carv'd the joints.
6150	The meat, prepar'd, he fix'd upon the spits:
6150	Patroclus kindled then a blazing fire;
6150	And when the fire burnt hotly, and the flame
6150	Subsided, spread the glowing embers out,
6150	And hung the spits above; then sprinkled o'er
6150	The meat with salt, and lifted from the stand.
6150	The viands cook'd and plac'd upon the board,
6150	From baskets fair Patroclus portion'd out
6150	The bread to each; the meat Achilles shar'd.
6150	Facing the sage Ulysses, sat the host
6150	On th' other side the tent; and bade his friend,
6150	Patroclus, give the Gods their honours due:
6150	He in the fire the wonted off'rings burnt:
6150	They on the viands set before them fell.
6150	The rage of thirst and hunger satisfied,
6150	Ajax to Phoenix sign'd: Ulysses saw
6150	The sign, and rising, fill'd a cup with wine,
6150	"And pledg'd Achilles thus: ""To thee I drink,"
6150	Achilles! nobly is thy table spread,
6150	As heretofore in Agamemnon's tent,
6150	So now in thine; abundant is the feast:
6150	But not the pleasures of the banquet now
6150	We have in hand: impending o'er our arms
6150	Grave cause of fear, illustrious chief, we see;
6150	Grave doubts, to save, or see destroy'd our ships,
6150	If thou, great warrior, put not forth thy might.
6150	For close beside the ships and wall are camp'd
6150	The haughty Trojans and renown'd allies:
6150	Their watch-fires frequent burn throughout the camp;
6150	And loud their boast that nought shall stay their hands,
6150	Until our dark-ribb'd ships be made their prey.
6150	Jove too for them, with fav'ring augury
6150	Sends forth his lightning; boastful of his strength,
6150	And firmly trusting in the aid of Jove,
6150	Hector, resistless, rages; nought he fears
6150	Or God or man, with martial fury fir'd.
6150	He prays, impatient, for th' approach of morn;
6150	Then, breaking through the lofty sterns, resolv'd
6150	To the devouring flames to give the ships,
6150	And slay the crews, bewilder'd in the smoke.
6150	And much my mind misgives me, lest the Gods
6150	His threats fulfil, and we be fated here
6150	To perish, far from Argos' grassy plains.
6150	Up then! if in their last extremity
6150	Thy spirit inclines, though late, to save the Greeks
6150	Sore press'd by Trojan arms: lest thou thyself
6150	Hereafter feel remorse; the evil done
6150	Is past all cure; then thou reflect betimes
6150	How from the Greeks to ward the day of doom.
6150	Dear friend, remember now thy father's words,
6150	The aged Peleus, when to Atreus' son
6150	He sent thee forth from Phthia, how he said,
6150	'My son, the boon of strength, if so they will,
6150	Juno or Pallas have the pow'r to give;
6150	But thou thyself thy haughty spirit must curb.
6150	For better far is gentle courtesy:
6150	And cease from angry strife, that so the Greeks
6150	The more may honour thee, both young and old.'
6150	Such were the words thine aged father spoke,
6150	Which thou hast now forgotten; yet, e'en now,
6150	Pause for awhile, and let thine anger cool;
6150	And noble gifts, so thou thy wrath remit,
6150	From Agamemnon shalt thou bear away.
6150	Listen to me, while I recount the gifts
6150	Which in his tent he pledg'd him to bestow.
6150	Sev'n tripods promis'd he, untouch'd by fire,
6150	Of gold, ten talents, twenty caldrons bright,
6150	Twelve pow'rful horses, in the course renown'd.
6150	Who by their speed have many prizes won.
6150	Not empty-handed could that man be deem'd,
6150	Nor poor in gold, who but so much possess'd
6150	As by those horses has for him been won.
6150	Sev'n women too, well skill'd in household cares,
6150	Lesbians, whom he selected for himself,
6150	That day thou captur'dst Lesbos' goodly isle,
6150	In beauty far surpassing all their sex.
6150	These will he give; and with them will he send
6150	The fair Briseis, her whom from thy tent
6150	He bore away; and add a solemn oath,
6150	He ne'er approach'd her bed, nor held with her
6150	Such intercourse as man with woman holds.
6150	All these shall now be thine: but if the Gods
6150	Shall grant us Priam's city to destroy,
6150	Of gold and brass, when we divide the spoil,
6150	With countless heaps a vessel shalt thou freight,
6150	And twenty captives thou thyself shalt choose,
6150	All only less than Argive Helen fair.
6150	And if it be our fate to see again
6150	The teeming soil of Argos, thou mayst be
6150	His son by marriage, and in honour held
6150	As is Orestes, who, his only son,
6150	Is rear'd at home in luxury, and ease.
6150	Three daughters fair are his, Chrysothemis,
6150	Iphianassa, and Laodice;
6150	Of these whiche'er thou wilt, to Peleus' house,
6150	No portion ask'd for, thou shalt take to wife;
6150	And with her will he add such wedding gifts,
6150	As never man before to daughter gave.
6150	Sev'n prosp'rous towns besides; Cardamyle,
6150	And Enope, and Ira's grassy plains,
6150	And Pherae, and Antheia's pastures deep,
6150	AEpeia fair, and vine-clad Pedasus;
6150	All by the sea, by sandy Pylos' bounds.
6150	The dwellers there in flocks and herds are rich,
6150	And, as a God, will honour thee with gifts,
6150	And to thy sceptre ample tribute pay.
6150	All these he gives, so thou thy wrath remit.
6150	But if thou hold Atrides in such hate,
6150	Him and his gifts, yet let thy pity rest
6150	On all the other Greeks, thus sore bested;
6150	By whom thou shalt be honour'd as a God:
6150	For great the triumph that thou now mayst gain;
6150	E'en Hector's self is now within thy reach;
6150	For he is near at hand; and in his pride
6150	And martial fury deems that none, of all
6150	"Our ships contain, can rival him in arms."""
6150	""
6150	Whom answer'd thus Achilles, swift of foot:
6150	"""Heav'n-born Ulysses, sage in council, son"
6150	Of great Laertes, I must frankly speak
6150	My mind at once, my fix'd resolve declare:
6150	That from henceforth I may not by the Greeks,
6150	By this man and by that, be importun'd.
6150	Him as the gates of hell my soul abhors,
6150	Whose outward words his secret thoughts belie,
6150	Hear then what seems to me the wisest course.
6150	On me nor Agamemnon, Atreus' son,
6150	Nor others shall prevail, since nought is gain'd
6150	By toil unceasing in the battle field.
6150	Who nobly fight, but share with those who skulk;
6150	Like honours gain the coward and the brave;
6150	Alike the idlers and the active die:
6150	And nought it profits me, though day by day
6150	In constant toil I set my life at stake;
6150	But as a bird, though ill she fare herself,
6150	Brings to her callow brood the food she takes,
6150	So I through many a sleepless night have lain,
6150	And many a bloody day have labour'd through,
6150	Engag'd in battle on your wives' behalf.
6150	Twelve cities have I taken with my ships;
6150	Eleven more by land, on Trojan soil:
6150	From all of these abundant stores of wealth
6150	I took, and all to Agamemnon gave;
6150	He, safe beside his ships, my spoils receiv'd,
6150	A few divided, but the most retain'd.
6150	To other chiefs and Kings he meted out
6150	Their sev'ral portions, and they hold them still;
6150	From me, from me alone of all the Greeks,
6150	He bore away, and keeps my cherish'd wife;
6150	Well! let him keep her, solace of his bed!
6150	But say then, why do Greeks with Trojans fight?
6150	Why hath Atrides brought this mighty host
6150	To Troy, if not in fair-hair'd Helen's cause?
6150	Of mortals are there none that love their wives,
6150	Save Atreus' sons alone? or do not all,
6150	Who boast the praise of sense and virtue, love
6150	And cherish each his own? as her I lov'd
6150	E'en from my soul, though captive of my spear.
6150	Now, since he once hath robb'd me, and deceiv'd,
6150	Let him not seek my aid; I know him now,
6150	And am not to be won; let him devise,
6150	With thee, Ulysses, and the other Kings,
6150	How best from hostile fires to save his ships.
6150	He hath completed many mighty works
6150	Without my aid; hath built a lofty wall,
6150	And dug a trench around it, wide and deep,
6150	And in the trench hath fix'd a palisade;
6150	Nor so the warrior-slayer Hector's might
6150	Can keep in check; while I was in the field,
6150	Not far without the walls would Hector range
6150	His line of battle, nor beyond the Oak
6150	And Scaean gates would venture; there indeed
6150	He once presum'd to meet me, hand to hand,
6150	And from my onset narrowly escap'd.
6150	But as with Hector now no more I fight,
6150	To-morrow morn, my off'rings made to Jove,
6150	And all the Gods, and freighted well my ships,
6150	And launch'd upon the main, thyself shall see,
6150	If that thou care to see, my vessels spread
6150	O'er the broad bosom of the Hellespont,
6150	My lusty crews plying the vig'rous oar;
6150	And if th' Earth-shaker send a fav'ring breeze,
6150	Three days will bear us home to Phthia's shore.
6150	There did I leave abundant store of wealth,
6150	When hitherward I took my luckless way;
6150	Thither from hence I bear, of ruddy gold,
6150	And brass, and women fair, and iron hoar
6150	The share assign'd me; but my chiefest prize
6150	The monarch Agamemnon, Atreus' son,
6150	Himself who gave, with insult takes away.
6150	To him then speak aloud the words I send,
6150	That all may know his crimes, if yet he hope
6150	Some other Greek by treach'rous wiles to cheat,
6150	Cloth'd as he is in shamelessness! my glance,
6150	All brazen as he is, he dare not meet.
6150	I share no more his counsels, nor his acts;
6150	He hath deceiv'd me once, and wrong'd; again
6150	He shall not cozen me! Of him, enough!
6150	I pass him by, whom Jove hath robb'd of sense.
6150	His gifts I loathe, and spurn; himself I hold
6150	At a hair's worth; and would he proffer me
6150	Tenfold or twentyfold of all he has,
6150	Or ever may be his; or all the gold
6150	Sent to Orchomenos or royal Thebes,
6150	Egyptian, treasurehouse of countless wealth,
6150	Who boasts her hundred gates, through each of which
6150	With horse and car two hundred warriors march:
6150	Nay, were his gifts in number as the sand,
6150	Or dust upon the plain, yet ne'er will I
6150	By Agamemnon be prevail'd upon,
6150	Till I have paid him back my heart's offence.
6150	Nor e'er of Agamemnon, Atreus' son,
6150	Will I a daughter wed; not were she fair
6150	As golden Venus, and in works renown'd
6150	As Pallas, blue-ey'd Maid, yet her e'en so
6150	I wed not; let him choose some other Greek,
6150	Some fitting match, of nobler blood than mine.
6150	But should the Gods in safety bring me home,
6150	At Peleus' hands I may receive a wife;
6150	And Greece can boast of many a lovely maid,
6150	In Hellas or in Phthia, daughters fair
6150	Of chiefs who hold their native fortresses:
6150	Of these, at will, a wife I may select:
6150	And ofttimes hath my warlike soul inclin'd
6150	To take a wedded wife, a fitting bride,
6150	And aged Peleus' wealth in peace enjoy.
6150	For not the stores which Troy, they say, contain'd
6150	In peaceful times, ere came the sons of Greece,
6150	Nor all the treasures which Apollo's shrine,
6150	The Archer-God, in rock-built Pythos holds,
6150	May weigh with life; of oxen and of sheep
6150	Successful forays may good store provide;
6150	And tripods may be gain'd, and noble steeds:
6150	But when the breath of man hath pass'd his lips,
6150	Nor strength nor foray can the loss repair.
6150	I by my Goddess-mother have been warn'd,
6150	The silver-footed Thetis, that o'er me
6150	A double chance of destiny impends:
6150	If here remaining, round the walls of Troy
6150	I wage the war, I ne'er shall see my home,
6150	But then undying glory shall be mine:
6150	If I return, and see my native land,
6150	My glory all is gone; but length of life
6150	Shall then be mine, and death be long deferr'd.
6150	If others ask'd my counsel, I should say,
6150	'Homeward direct your course; of lofty Troy
6150	Ye see not yet the end; all-seeing Jove
6150	O'er her extends his hand; on him relying
6150	Her people all with confidence are fill'd.'
6150	Go then; my answer to the chiefs of Greece
6150	Speak boldly--such the privilege of age--
6150	Bid that some better counsel they devise
6150	To save their ships and men; their present scheme,
6150	My anger unappeas'd, avails them nought.
6150	But Phoenix here shall stay, and sleep to-night;
6150	And with the morrow he with me shall sail
6150	And seek our native land, if so he will:
6150	"For not by force will I remove him hence."""
6150	""
6150	He said; they all, confounded by his words,
6150	In silence heard; so sternly did he speak.
6150	At length, in tears, the aged Phoenix spoke,
6150	For greatly fear'd he for the ships of Greece:
6150	"""If, great Achilles, on returning home"
6150	Thy mind is set, nor canst thou be induc'd
6150	To save the ships from fire, so fierce thy wrath;
6150	How then, dear boy, can I remain behind,
6150	Alone? whom with thee aged Peleus sent,
6150	That day when he in Agamemnon's cause
6150	From Phthia sent thee, inexperienc'd yet
6150	In all the duties of confed'rate war,
6150	And sage debate, on which attends renown.
6150	Me then he sent, instructor of thy youth,
6150	To prompt thy language, and thine acts to guide.
6150	So not from thee, dear boy, can I consent
6150	To part, though Heav'n should undertake my age
6150	To prompt thy language, and thine acts to guide.
6150	So not from thee, dear boy, can I consent
6150	To part, though Heav'n should undertake my age
6150	To wipe away, and vig'rous youth restore,
6150	Such as I boasted, when from Greece I fled
6150	Before my angry sire, Amyntor, son
6150	Of Ormenus; a fair-hair'd concubine
6150	Cause of the quarrel; her my father lov'd,
6150	And by her love estrang'd, despis'd his wife,
6150	My mother; oft she pray'd me to seduce,
6150	To vex th' old man, my father's concubine;
6150	I yielded; he, suspecting, on my head
6150	A curse invok'd, and on the Furies call'd
6150	His curse to witness, that upon his knees
6150	No child, by me begotten, e'er should sit:
6150	His curse the Gods have heard, and ratified,
6150	Th' infernal King, and awful Proserpine.
6150	Then would I fain have slain him with the sword,
6150	Had not some God my rising fury quell'd,
6150	And set before my mind the public voice,
6150	The odium I should have to bear 'mid Greeks,
6150	If branded with the name of patricide.
6150	But longer in my angry father's house
6150	To dwell, my spirit brook'd not, though my friends
6150	And kinsmen all besought me to remain;
6150	And many a goodly sheep, and many a steer
6150	They slew, and many swine, with fat o'erlaid,
6150	They sing'd, and roasted o'er the burning coals;
6150	And drank in many a cup the old man's wine.
6150	Nine nights they kept me in continual watch,
6150	By turns relieving guards. The fires meanwhile
6150	Burnt constant: one beneath the porch that fac'd
6150	The well-fenc'd court; one in the vestibule
6150	Before my chamber door. The tenth dark night
6150	My chamber's closely-fitting doors I broke,
6150	And lightly vaulted o'er the court-yard fence,
6150	By guards alike and servant maids unmark'd.
6150	Through all the breadth of Hellas then I fled,
6150	Until at length to Phthia's fruitful soil,
6150	Mother of flocks, to Peleus' realm I came,
6150	Who kindly welcom'd me, and with such love
6150	As to his only son, his well-belov'd,
6150	A father shows, his gen'rous gifts bestow'd.
6150	He gave me wealth, he gave me ample rule;
6150	And on the bounds of Phthia bade me dwell,
6150	And o'er the Dolopes hold sov'reign sway.
6150	Thee too, Achilles, rival of the Gods,
6150	Such, as thou art I made thee; from my soul
6150	I lov'd thee; nor wouldst thou with others go
6150	Or to the meal, or in the house be fed,
6150	Till on my knee thou satt'st, and by my hand
6150	Thy food were cut, the cup were tender'd thee;
6150	And often, in thy childish helplessness.
6150	The bosom of my dress with wine was drench'd;
6150	Such care I had of thee, such pains I took,
6150	Rememb'ring that by Heav'n's decree, no son
6150	Of mine I e'er might see; then thee I made,
6150	Achilles, rival of the Gods, my son,
6150	That thou mightst be the guardian of mine age.
6150	But thou, Achilles, curb thy noble rage;
6150	A heart implacable beseems thee not.
6150	The Gods themselves, in virtue, honour, strength,
6150	Excelling thee, may yet be mollified;
6150	For they, when mortals have transgress'd, or fail'd
6150	To do aright, by sacrifice and pray'r,
6150	Libations and burnt-off'rings, may be sooth'd.
6150	Pray'rs are the daughters of immortal Jove;
6150	But halt, and wrinkled, and of feeble sight,
6150	They plod in Ate's track; while Ate, strong
6150	And swift of foot, outstrips their laggard pace,
6150	And, dealing woe to man, o'er all the earth
6150	Before them flies: they, following, heal her wounds.
6150	Him who with honour welcomes their approach,
6150	They greatly aid, and hear him when he prays;
6150	But who rejects, and sternly casts them off,
6150	To Saturn's son they go, and make their pray'r
6150	That Ate follow him and claim her dues.
6150	Then to the daughters of immortal Jove,
6150	Do thou, Achilles, show the like respect,
6150	That many another brave man's heart hath sway'd.
6150	If to thy tent no gifts Atrides brought,
6150	With promises of more, but still retain'd
6150	His vehement enmity, I could not ask
6150	That thou thy cherish'd anger shouldst discard,
6150	And aid the Greeks, how great so-e'er their need.
6150	But now large off'rings hath he giv'n, and more
6150	Hath promis'd; and, of all the Greeks, hath sent
6150	To pray thine aid, the men thou lov'st the best.
6150	Discredit not their mission, nor their words.
6150	Till now, I grant thee, none could blame thy wrath.
6150	In praise of men in ancient days renown'd,
6150	This have we heard, that how-so-e'er might rage
6150	Their hostile feuds, their anger might be still
6150	By gifts averted, and by words appeas'd.
6150	One case I bear in mind, in times long past,
6150	And not in later days; and here, 'mid friends,
6150	How all occurr'd, will I at length recite.
6150	Time was, that with AEtolia's warlike bands
6150	Round Calydon the Acarnanians fought
6150	With mutual slaughter; these to save the town,
6150	The Acarnanians burning to destroy.
6150	This curse of war the golden-throned Queen
6150	Diana sent, in anger that from her
6150	OEneus the first-fruits of his field withheld.
6150	The other Gods their hecatombs receiv'd;
6150	Diana's shrine alone no off'rings deck'd,
6150	Neglected, or o'erlook'd; the sin was great;
6150	And in her wrath the arrow-darting Queen
6150	A savage wild-boar sent, with gleaming tusks,
6150	Which OEneus' vineyard haunting, wrought him harm.
6150	There laid he prostrate many a stately tree,
6150	With root and branch, with blossom and with fruit.
6150	Him Meleager, son of OEneus, slew,
6150	With youths and dogs from all the neighbouring towns
6150	Collected; smaller force had not avail'd,
6150	So huge he was, so fierce; and many a youth
6150	Had by his tusks been laid upon the bier.
6150	A fierce contention then the Goddess rais'd,
6150	For the boar's head and bristly hide, between
6150	The Acarnanian and th' AEtolian bands.
6150	While warlike Meleager kept the field,
6150	So long the Acarnanians far'd but ill;
6150	Nor dar'd, despite the numbers of their host,
6150	Maintain their ground before the city walls.
6150	When he to anger yielded, which sometimes
6150	Swells in the bosom e'en of wisest men,
6150	Incens'd against his mother, he withdrew
6150	To Cleopatra fair, his wedded wife;
6150	(Marpessa her, Evenus' daughter, bore
6150	To Idas, strongest man of all who then
6150	Were living, who against Apollo's self
6150	For the neat-footed maiden bent his bow.
6150	Her parents call'd the child Alcyone,
6150	In mem'ry of the tears her mother shed,
6150	Rival of Alcyon's melancholy fate,
6150	When by far-darting Phoebus forc'd away).
6150	With her, retiring from the field, he nurs'd
6150	His wrath; resenting thus his mother's curse,
6150	Althaea; she her brother's death bore hard,
6150	And pray'd to Heav'n above, and with her hands
6150	Beating the solid earth, the nether pow'rs,
6150	Pluto and awful Proserpine, implor'd,
6150	Down on her knees, her bosom wet with tears,
6150	Death on her son invoking; from the depths
6150	Of Erebus Erinnys heard her pray'r,
6150	Gloom-haunting Goddess, dark and stern of heart.
6150	Soon round the gates the din of battle rose,
6150	The tow'rs by storm assaulted; then his aid
6150	Th' AEtonian Elders and the sacred priests
6150	With promises of great reward implor'd.
6150	A fruitful plot they bade him set apart,
6150	The richest land in lovely Calydon,
6150	Of fifty acres: half for vineyard meet,
6150	And half of fertile plain, for tillage clear'd.
6150	Upon the threshold of his lofty rooms
6150	Old OEneus stood, and at the portals clos'd
6150	He knock'd in vain, a suppliant to his son.
6150	His sisters and his brother join'd their pray'rs,
6150	But sterner his rejection of their suit;
6150	The friends he valued most, and lov'd the best,
6150	Yet they too fail'd his fix'd resolve to shake;
6150	Till to his very doors the war had reach'd,
6150	The foe upon the tow'rs, the town in flames:
6150	Then Meleager's beauteous wife, at length,
6150	In tears, beseeching him, the thousand ills
6150	Recall'd, which on a captur'd town attend;
6150	The slaughter'd men, the city burnt with fire,
6150	The helpless children and deep-bosom'd dames
6150	A prey to strangers. List'ning to the tale,
6150	His spirit was rous'd within him; and again
6150	He took the field, and donn'd his glitt'ring arms.
6150	Thus did his act from doom th' AEtolians save
6150	Spontaneous; yet he gain'd not, though he sav'd,
6150	The rich reward they once were pledg'd to give.
6150	But be not thou like him, nor let thy God
6150	Turn thitherward thy thoughts; our ships on fire,
6150	Thine aid will less be priz'd; come, take the gifts,
6150	And as a God be honour'd by the Greeks.
6150	If thou hereafter, unsolicited,
6150	The battle join, the Greeks thou mayst protect,
6150	"But not an equal share of honour gain."""
6150	""
6150	Whom answer'd thus Achilles, swift of foot:
6150	"""Phoenix, my second father, rev'rend sire,"
6150	Such honours move me not; my honour comes
6150	From Jove, whose will it is that I should here
6150	Remain beside the ships, while I retain
6150	Breath in my lungs and vigour in my limbs.
6150	This too I say, and bear it in thy mind:
6150	Disturb me not with weeping and complaints,
6150	To do Atrides grace; if him thou love,
6150	My love for thee perchance may turn to hate:
6150	My friend should honour him who honours me.
6150	But come with me, and of my kingdom half,
6150	And equal honours shalt thou share with me.
6150	These shall our message bear; stay thou the while,
6150	And on soft couch repose; to-morrow morn
6150	"Will we determine or to sail or stay."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and with his eyebrows gave a sign
6150	In silence to Patroclus, to prepare
6150	A bed for Phoenix, that without delay
6150	The rest might leave the tent; then thus began
6150	Ajax, the godlike son of Telamon:
6150	"""Ulysses sage, Laertes' high-born son,"
6150	Depart we now; for this way our discourse
6150	Can lead to no result; behoves us bear
6150	Our tidings, all unwelcome as they are,
6150	Back to the chiefs awaiting our return.
6150	Achilles hath allow'd his noble heart
6150	To cherish rancour and malignant hate;
6150	Nor reeks he of his old companions' love,
6150	Wherewith we honour'd him above the rest.
6150	Relentless he! a son's or brother's death,
6150	By payment of a fine, may be aton'd;
6150	The slayer may remain in peace at home,
6150	The debt discharg'd; the other will forego,
6150	The forfeiture receiv'd, his just revenge;
6150	But thou maintain'st a stern, obdurate mood.
6150	And for a single girl! we offer sev'n,
6150	Surpassing fair, and other gifts to boot.
6150	We now bespeak thy courtesy; respect
6150	Thy hearth; remember that beneath thy roof
6150	We stand, deputed by the gen'ral voice
6150	Of all the host; and fain would claim to be,
6150	"Of all the Greeks, thy best and dearest friends."""
6150	""
6150	Whom answer'd thus Achilles, swift of foot:
6150	"""Illustrious Ajax, son of Telamon,"
6150	Without offence hast thou thy message giv'n;
6150	But fury fills my soul, whene'er I think
6150	How Agamemnon, 'mid th' assembled Greeks,
6150	Insulting, held me forth to public scorn,
6150	As some dishonour'd, houseless vagabond.
6150	But go ye now, and bear my answer back:
6150	No more in bloody war will I engage,
6150	Till noble Hector, Priam's godlike son,
6150	O'er slaughter'd Greeks, your ships enwrapp'd in fire,
6150	Shall reach the quarters of the Myrmidons.
6150	Ere he assail my ship and tents, I think
6150	"That Hector, valiant as he is, will pause."""
6150	Thus he: they each the double goblet rais'd,
6150	And, to the Gods their due libations pour'd,
6150	Ulysses leading, to the ships return'd.
6150	""
6150	Meanwhile Patroclus bade th' attendant maids
6150	Prepare a bed for Phoenix; they obey'd,
6150	And quickly laid the bed with fleeces warm,
6150	And rugs, and linen light and fine o'erspread.
6150	There slept th' old man, and waited for the morn.
6150	Within the tent's recess Achilles slept;
6150	And by his side, from Lesbos captive brought,
6150	Daughter of Phorbas, Diomede fair;
6150	On th' other side Patroclus lay; with him
6150	The graceful Iphis, whom, when Scyros' isle
6150	He captur'd, and Enyes' rock-built fort,
6150	Achilles to his lov'd companion gave.
6150	""
6150	When to Atrides' tent the envoys came,
6150	The chiefs, uprising, pledg'd them one by one
6150	In golden goblets; then their tidings ask'd.
6150	First Agamemnon, King of men, enquir'd:
6150	"""Tell me, renown'd Ulysses, pride of Greece,"
6150	What says he: will he save our ships from fire,
6150	"Or still, in wrathful mood, withhold his aid?"""
6150	""
6150	To whom again Ulysses, stout of heart:
6150	"""Most mighty Agamemnon, King of men,"
6150	His anger is not quench'd, but fiercer still
6150	It glows; thy gifts and thee alike he spurns;
6150	He bids thee with the other chiefs concert
6150	The means thy people and thy ships to save;
6150	And menaces himself at early dawn
6150	To launch his well-trimm'd vessels on the main.
6150	Nay more, he counsels others, so he says,
6150	Homeward to turn, since here of lofty Troy
6150	We see not yet the end; all-seeing Jove
6150	O'er her extends his hand; on him relying,
6150	Her people all with confidence are fill'd.
6150	Such was his language; here before you stand
6150	Ajax and both the heralds, sage, grave men,
6150	Who with me went, and will confirm my words.
6150	Old Phoenix left we there, so will'd the chief,
6150	That with the morrow he with him may sail,
6150	And seek their native land, if so he will;
6150	"For not by force will he remove him hence."""
6150	""
6150	Ulysses thus; they all in silence heard,
6150	Amaz'd, so stern the message that he bore.
6150	Long time in silence sat the chiefs of Greece.
6150	Outspoke at length the valiant Diomed:
6150	"""Most mighty Agamemnon, King of men,"
6150	Would that thou ne'er hadst stoop'd with costly gifts
6150	To sue for aid from Peleus' matchless son;
6150	For he before was over-proud, and now
6150	Thine offers will have tenfold swoll'n his pride.
6150	But leave we him, according to his will,
6150	To go or stay: he then will join the fight,
6150	When his own spirit shall prompt, or Heav'n inspire.
6150	But hear ye all, and do as I advise:
6150	Refresh'd with food and wine (for therein lie
6150	Both strength and courage), turn we to our rest;
6150	And when the rosy-finger'd morn appears,
6150	Thyself among the foremost, with bold hearts,
6150	"Before our ships both horse and foot array."""
6150	""
6150	He said; and all the chiefs with loud applause
6150	His speech confirm'd; then, due libations pour'd,
6150	Each to his sev'ral tent they all withdrew;
6150	Then laid them down, and sought the boon of sleep.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	ARGUMENT.
6150	""
6150	THE NIGHT ADVENTURE OF DIOMED AND ULYSSES.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	Upon the refusal of Achilles to return to the army, the distress of
6150	Agamemnon is described in the most lively manner. He takes no rest that
6150	night, but passes through the camp, awaking the leaders, and contriving
6150	all possible methods for the public safety. Menelaus, Nestor, Ulysses,
6150	and Diomed, are employed in raising the rest of the captains. They call
6150	a council of war, and determine to send scouts into the enemy's camp,
6150	to learn their posture, and discover their intentions. Diomed
6150	undertakes the hazardous enterprise, and makes choice of Ulysses for
6150	his companion. In their passage they surprise Dolon, whom Hector had
6150	sent on a like design to the camp of the Grecians. From him they are
6150	informed of the situation of the Trojans and auxiliary forces, and
6150	particularly of Rhesus, and the Thracians, who were lately arrived.
6150	They pass on with success; kill Rhesus with several of his officers,
6150	and seize the famous horses of that prince, with which they return in
6150	triumph to the camp.
6150	""
6150	The same night continues; the scene lies in the two camps.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	BOOK X.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	In night-long slumbers lay the other chiefs
6150	Of all the Greeks, by gentle sleep subdued;
6150	But not on Agamemnon, Atreus' son,
6150	By various cares oppress'd, sweet slumber fell.
6150	As when from Jove, the fair-hair'd Juno's Lord,
6150	Flashes the lightning, bringing in its train
6150	Tempestuous storm of mingled rain and hail
6150	Or snow, by winter sprinkled o'er the fields;
6150	Or op'ning wide the rav'nous jaws of war;
6150	So Agamemnon from his inmost heart
6150	Pour'd forth in groans his multitudinous grief,
6150	His spirit within him sinking. On the plain
6150	He look'd, and there, alarm'd, the watchfires saw,
6150	Which, far advanc'd before the walls of Troy,
6150	Blaz'd numberless; and thence of pipes and flutes
6150	He heard the sound, and busy hum of men.
6150	Upon the ships he look'd, and men of Greece,
6150	And by the roots his hair in handfuls tore
6150	To Jove on high; deep groan'd his mighty heart.
6150	Thus as he mus'd, the wisest course appear'd,
6150	With Nestor, son of Neleus, to confer,
6150	If they some scheme in council might devise
6150	To ward destruction from the Grecian host.
6150	He rose, and o'er his body drew his vest,
6150	And underneath his well-turn'd feet he bound
6150	His sandals fair; then o'er his shoulders threw,
6150	Down reaching to his feet, a lion's skin,
6150	Tawny and vast; then grasp'd his pond'rous spear.
6150	""
6150	On Menelaus weigh'd an equal dread;
6150	Nor on his eyes that night had slumber sat,
6150	Lest ill befall the Greeks; who, in his cause,
6150	Crossing the wat'ry waste, had come to Troy,
6150	And bold defiance to the Trojans giv'n.
6150	Round his broad chest a panther's skin he threw;
6150	Then on his head his brazen helmet plac'd,
6150	And in his brawny hand a lance he bore.
6150	To meet his brother went he forth, of Greece
6150	The mighty monarch, as a God rever'd.
6150	Him by the ship he found, in act to arm;
6150	And welcome was his presence to the King.
6150	""
6150	Then valiant Menelaus first began:
6150	"""Why thus in arms, good brother? seek'st thou one"
6150	The Trojan camp to spy? I greatly fear
6150	That none will undertake the task, alone
6150	To spy the movements of the hostile camp
6150	"In the dark night: stout-hearted he must be."""
6150	""
6150	To whom the monarch Agamemnon thus:
6150	"""Great need, my noble brother, have we both"
6150	Of sagest counsels, if we hope the Greeks
6150	And Grecian ships from ruin to preserve,
6150	Since turn'd against us is the mind of Jove.
6150	To Hector's off'rings most his soul inclines;
6150	For never have I seen, or heard men tell,
6150	How in one day one man has wrought such loss
6150	As Hector, dear to Jove, yet not the son
6150	Of God or Goddess, on the Greeks has wrought.
6150	Such deeds hath he achiev'd, such havoc made,
6150	As we shall long in bitter mem'ry keep.
6150	Haste thou amid the ships, and hither bring
6150	Idomeneus and Ajax; I the while
6150	Will Nestor rouse, and urge that he with us
6150	The outposts visit, and instruct the guard.
6150	To him they best will listen; for his son
6150	Commands the watch; with him Meriones,
6150	The follower of the King Idomeneus:
6150	"To them by pref'rence hath this charge been giv'n."""
6150	""
6150	He said: and Menelaus answer'd thus:
6150	"""What wouldst thou have me do then? here remain"
6150	With them, and wait thy coming, or to them
6150	"Thy message give, and follow in thy steps?"""
6150	""
6150	Him answer'd Agamemnon, King of men:
6150	"""Remain thou here, lest haply we might fail"
6150	To meet; for in the camp are many paths.
6150	But thou, where'er thou go'st, each sev'ral man
6150	Address, and ask to rise; to each his name
6150	And patronymic giving; pay to each
6150	All due respect; nor bear thee haughtily;
6150	We like the rest must share the load of toil.
6150	"Which Jove assigns to all of mortal birth."""
6150	""
6150	His brother thus with counsels wise dismiss'd,
6150	The King to aged Nestor took his way:
6150	Him by his tent and dark-ribb'd ship he found
6150	On a soft couch; beside him lay his arms,
6150	His shield, two lances, and a glitt'ring helm:
6150	There lay the rich-wrought belt the old man wore,
6150	When to the battle, arm'd, he led his troops;
6150	For nought to age's weakness would he yield.
6150	Raising his head, and on his elbow propp'd,
6150	"He question'd thus Atrides: ""Who art thou,"
6150	That wand'rest through th' encampment thus alone,
6150	In the dark night, when other mortals sleep?
6150	Seek'st thou some mule broke loose, or comrade lost?
6150	"Speak, nor in silence come; what wouldst thou here?"""
6150	""
6150	To whom thus Agamemnon, King of men:
6150	"""O Nestor! son of Neleus, pride of Greece,"
6150	Know me for Agamemnon, Atreus' son,
6150	On whom hath Jove, beyond the lot of men,
6150	Laid grief that ne'er shall end, while I retain
6150	Breath in my lungs, and vigour in my limbs.
6150	I wander thus, because these eyes of mine
6150	Sweet slumber visits not, by cares of war
6150	Oppress'd, and harass'd by the woes of Greece.
6150	Much for the Greeks I fear; nor keeps my mind
6150	Its wonted firmess; I am ill at ease;
6150	And leaps my troubled heart as tho' 'twould burst
6150	My bosom's bounds; my limbs beneath me shake.
6150	But if thou wilt, since thou too know'st not sleep,
6150	Together to the outposts let us go,
6150	And see if there, by toil and sleep o'erpow'r'd,
6150	The guard repose, neglectful of their watch.
6150	The foe is close at hand; nor are we sure
6150	"He may not hazard e'en a night attack."""
6150	""
6150	To whom Gerenian Nestor thus replied;
6150	"""Most mighty Agamemnon, King of men,"
6150	Not all the hopes that Hector entertains
6150	Shall by the Lord of counsel be fulfill'd;
6150	For him are toil and danger yet in store,
6150	If but Achilles of his wrath repent.
6150	Gladly will I attend thee; others too,
6150	Tydides, spearman bold, Ulysses sage,
6150	Ajax the swift, and Phyleus' noble son,
6150	Should all be summon'd; and 'twere well that one
6150	Across the camp should run, to call in haste
6150	The godlike Ajax, and Idomeneus;
6150	Theirs are the farthest ships, nor near at hand.
6150	But, dear to me as Menelaus is,
6150	And highly honour'd, I must blame, that thus
6150	(Though thou shouldst take offence, I needs must say)
6150	He sleeps, and leaves the toil to thee alone.
6150	With all the chiefs he should be busied now,
6150	"Imploring aid, in this our utmost need."""
6150	""
6150	To whom thus Agamemnon, King of men:
6150	"""For other times, old man, reserve thy blame;"
6150	Sometimes, I own, he lags behind, nor takes
6150	His share of labour; not from indolence,
6150	Or want of sense; but still regarding me;
6150	Waiting from me an impulse to receive.
6150	But now, before me he was up, and came
6150	To visit me; and I have sent him on
6150	To call those very men whom thou hast nam'd.
6150	Come then; for we, beside the gates, and guard
6150	"Shall find them; there my orders were to meet."""
6150	""
6150	To whom Gerenian Nestor thus replied;
6150	"""Then none can blame him; nor can any Greek"
6150	"Justly refuse his summons to obey."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and round his body wrapped his vest;
6150	Then on his feet his sandals fair he bound,
6150	And o'er his shoulders clasp'd a purple cloak,
6150	Doubled, with ample folds, and downy pile;
6150	Then took his spear, with point of sharpen'd brass,
6150	And through the camp prepar'd to take his way.
6150	Gerenian Nestor from his slumbers first
6150	Ulysses, sage as Jove in council, rous'd,
6150	Loud shouting; soon the voice his senses reach'd;
6150	Forth from his tent he came, and thus he spoke:
6150	"""What cause so urgent leads you, through the camp,"
6150	"In the dark night to wander thus alone?"""
6150	""
6150	To whom Gerenian Nestor thus replied:
6150	"""Ulysses sage, Laertes' godlike son,"
6150	Be not offended; such the stress that now
6150	Weighs down our army; come thou then with us,
6150	And others let us call; with whom 'tis meet
6150	"That we should counsel take, to fight or fly."""
6150	""
6150	He said; Ulysses to the tent return'd;
6150	Then, his broad shield across his shoulders thrown,
6150	Came forth again, and with them took his way.
6150	To Diomed, the son of Tydeus, next
6150	They went; and him they found beside his arms,
6150	Without his tent; his comrades slept around,
6150	Their heads upon their bucklers laid; their spears
6150	Stood upright, on the butts; the burnish'd brass
6150	Like Heav'n's own lightning, flashing far around.
6150	Stretch'd on a wild bull's hide the chief repos'd,
6150	A gay-wrought carpet roll'd beneath his head.
6150	Gerenian Nestor close behind him stood,
6150	And touched him with his foot, and thus in tone
6150	"Reproachful spoke: ""Arouse thee, Tydeus' son!"
6150	Why sleep'st thou thus all night? or know'st thou not
6150	That on the very margin of the plain,
6150	And close beside the ships the Trojans lie,
6150	"And little space between the camps is left?"""
6150	""
6150	Quick rous'd from sleep, thus answer'd Diomed:
6150	"""Beshrew thy heart, old man! no labour seems"
6150	For thee too hard; are there not younger men
6150	To run about the camp, and summon all
6150	"The sev'ral chiefs? thou dost too much, old man."""
6150	""
6150	To whom Gerenian Nestor thus replied:
6150	"""True, friend, and full of wisdom are thy words;"
6150	Good sons indeed I have, and followers brave
6150	And many, who might well my message bear;
6150	But great is now the stress that lies on Greece;
6150	For on a razor's edge is balanc'd now,
6150	To all the Greeks, the chance of life or death.
6150	Do thou then go (for thou my younger art),
6150	And if thou pity me, thyself arouse
6150	"Ajax the swift, and Phyleus' noble son."""
6150	He said; the warrior round his shoulders threw,
6150	Down reaching to his feet, a lion's hide,
6150	Tawny and dark; and took his pond'rous spear.
6150	He went, arous'd, and with him brought the chiefs.
6150	""
6150	When to the guard they came, not sunk in sleep
6150	Found they the leaders; but on wakeful watch
6150	Intent, and all alert beside their arms.
6150	As round a sheepfold keep their anxious watch
6150	The dogs, who in the neighbouring thicket hear
6150	Some beast, that, bold in search of prey, has come
6150	Down from the mountain; loud the clamours rise
6150	Of men and dogs; all sleep is banish'd thence;
6150	So from their eyes was banish'd sleep, who watch'd
6150	Through that disastrous night; still plainward turning
6150	At ev'ry movement in the Trojan camp.
6150	The old man saw, well-pleas'd; and thus address'd
6150	With cheering words the captains of the guard:
6150	"""Watch ever thus, good youths; nor be surpris'd"
6150	"By slumber, lest the foe a triumph gain."""
6150	""
6150	This said, he cross'd the ditch, and with him went
6150	The Grecian leaders, to the council call'd:
6150	With them, admitted to the conf'rence, went
6150	Meriones, and Nestor's noble son.
6150	The deep-dug ditch they cross'd, and sat them down
6150	Upon an open space, from corpses clear;
6150	Where Hector from the slaughter of the Greeks
6150	Turn'd back, when Ev'ning spread her veil around:
6150	There sat they down, and there the conf'rence held.
6150	Gerenian Nestor first took up the word:
6150	"""O friends! is any here with heart so bold"
6150	Who dares, self-confident, the Trojan camp
6150	To enter? there some straggler he might take,
6150	Or in the camp itself some tidings gain,
6150	What are their secret counsels; if they mean
6150	Here by the ships to hold their ground, or back,
6150	Sated with vict'ry, to the town retire.
6150	This could he learn, and hither scatheless bring
6150	His tidings, high as Heav'n in all men's mouths
6150	Would be his praise, and ample his reward.
6150	For ev'ry captain of a ship should give
6150	A coal-black ewe, and at her foot a lamb,
6150	A prize beyond compare; and high should be
6150	"His place at banquets and at solemn feasts."""
6150	""
6150	He said; but all the chiefs in silence heard;
6150	Then rose the valiant Diomed, and said:
6150	"""Nestor, that heart is mine; I dare alone"
6150	Enter the hostile camp, so close at hand;
6150	Yet were one comrade giv'n me, I should go
6150	With more of comfort, more of confidence.
6150	Where two combine, one before other sees
6150	The better course; and ev'n though one alone
6150	The readiest way discover, yet would be
6150	"His judgment slower, his decision less."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and many chiefs to Diomed
6150	Proffer'd companionship; stood forth at once,
6150	With him to penetrate the Trojan camp,
6150	The two Ajaces, ministers of Mars;
6150	Stood forth Meriones, and eagerly
6150	Stood forth the son of Nestor; Atreus' son,
6150	The royal Menelaus, spearman bold,
6150	And stout Ulysses, whose enduring heart
6150	For ev'ry deed of valour was prepar'd.
6150	Rose Agamemnon, King of men, and said:
6150	"""Tydides, comrade dearest to my soul,"
6150	Choose thou thine own companion, whom thou wilt;
6150	Of all the many here that proffer aid
6150	Him whom thou deem'st the best; nor from respect
6150	To persons leave the better man behind,
6150	And take the worse; nor def'rence show to rank,
6150	"Not though the purest royal blood were his."""
6150	""
6150	In fear for Menelaus thus he spoke:
6150	Then answer'd valiant Diomed, and said;
6150	"""If my companion I may freely choose,"
6150	How can I pass the sage Ulysses by?
6150	Of ready wit, and dauntless courage, prov'd
6150	In ev'ry danger; and to Pallas dear.
6150	I should not fear, by him accompanied,
6150	To pass through fire, and safely both return;
6150	"So far in prudence he surpasses all."""
6150	""
6150	Whom answer'd thus Ulysses, stout of heart:
6150	"""Tydides, nor exaggerated praise"
6150	Bestow on me, nor censure; for thou speak'st
6150	To those who know me all for what I am.
6150	But go we; night wanes fast, the morn is near:
6150	The stars are high in Heav'n; and of the night
6150	"Two thirds are spent, one third alone remains."""
6150	""
6150	He said; and both prepar'd to don their arms.
6150	The youthful warrior Thrasymedes gave
6150	To Diomed a two-edg'd sword (his own
6150	Had in the ship been left) and ample shield;
6150	Then on his brows a leathern headpiece plac'd,
6150	Without or peak or plume; a simple casque,
6150	Such as is worn by youths to guard their head.
6150	A bow, and well-fill'd quiver, and a sword,
6150	Meriones to sage Ulysses gave;
6150	And on his brows a leathern headpiece plac'd,
6150	Well wrought within, with num'rous straps secur'd,
6150	And on th' outside, with wild boars' gleaming tusks
6150	Profusely garnish'd, scatter'd here and there
6150	By skilful hand; the midst with felt was lin'd;
6150	This from Amyntor, son of Ormenus,
6150	Autolycus from Eleon bore away,
6150	Spoil of his pillag'd house; Autolycus
6150	Gave to Amphidamas, Cytheran chief,
6150	Who in Scandea dwelt; Amphidamas
6150	To Molus, pledge of friendship; he again
6150	Gave to his son, Meriones, from whom
6150	It now encircled sage Ulysses' brow.
6150	Thus with accoutrements and arms supplied,
6150	They left their brother chiefs, and took their way.
6150	Then close beside their path, by Pallas sent,
6150	Rose, on the right, a heron; through the gloom
6150	They saw it not indeed, but heard the cry.
6150	The fav'ring sign with joy Ulysses hail'd,
6150	"And thus to Pallas pray'd: ""Hear me, thou child"
6150	Of aegis-bearing Jove, who still hast stood
6150	In ev'ry peril at my side, whose eye
6150	My ev'ry movement sees; now, Goddess, now
6150	Befriend me; grant that safe, with triumph crown'd,
6150	We may return, some great exploit achiev'd,
6150	"Such as the Trojans long may bear in mind."""
6150	""
6150	Him following, thus the brave Tydides pray'd:
6150	"""My voice too, child of Jove, undaunted, hear;"
6150	And be with me, as with my father erst,
6150	The godlike Tydeus, when to Thebes he went,
6150	An envoy, in advance; and left behind,
6150	Upon Asopus' banks the mail-clad Greeks.
6150	Smooth was the message which to Thebes he bore;
6150	But great, his mission ended, were the deeds
6150	That with thine aid he wrought; for, Goddess, thou
6150	Wast with him, and thine arm was his defence:
6150	So be thou now with me, and me defend.
6150	Then on thine altar will I sacrifice
6150	A yearling heifer, broad of brow, untam'd,
6150	Whereon no yoke hath mortal ever laid:
6150	"Her will I give, and tip her horns with gold."""
6150	""
6150	Thus as they pray'd, their pray'r the Goddess heard;
6150	Then, their devotions ended, on they far'd
6150	Through the deep dead of night, like lions twain,
6150	'Mid slaughter, corpses, arms, and blacken'd gore.
6150	""
6150	Nor, in the Trojan camp, did Hector leave
6150	The chiefs to rest; but all to conf'rence call'd,
6150	The leaders and the councillors of Troy;
6150	To whom his prudent speech he thus address'd:
6150	"""Who is there here, that for a rich reward"
6150	A noble work will undertake? A car
6150	And two strong-collar'd horses, best of all
6150	That can be found within the Grecian lines,
6150	Shall he receive, who, to his endless praise,
6150	Shall dare approach the ships; and learn if still
6150	They keep their wonted watch, or, by our arms
6150	Subdued and vanquished, meditate retreat,
6150	"And, worn with toil, the nightly watch neglect."""
6150	Thus Hector spoke; but all in silence heard.
6150	""
6150	There was one Dolon in the Trojan camp,
6150	The herald's son, Eumedes; rich in gold
6150	And brass; not fair of face, but swift of foot;
6150	Amid five sisters he the only son;
6150	Who thus to Hector and the Trojans spoke:
6150	""
6150	"""Hector, with dauntless courage I will dare"
6150	Approach the ships, and bring thee tidings sure;
6150	But hold thou forth thy royal staff, and swear
6150	That I the horses and the brass-bound car
6150	Shall have, the boast of Peleus' matchless son:
6150	Not vain shall be my errand, nor deceive
6150	Thy hopes; right through the camp I mean to pass
6150	To Agamemnon's tent, where all the chiefs
6150	"Debate in council, or to fight or fly."""
6150	""
6150	He said; and Hector took his royal staff,
6150	"And swore to him: ""Be witness Jove himself,"
6150	The Lord of thunder, that no Trojan man,
6150	Thyself except, shall e'er those horses drive;
6150	"For thee they are reserv'd, a glorious prize."""
6150	""
6150	Thus Hector swore; though unfulfill'd the oath.
6150	The hope to Dolon fresh assurance gave.
6150	Forthwith, his bow across his shoulders slung,
6150	A grisly wolf-skin o'er it, on his head
6150	A cap of marten's fur, and in his hand
6150	A jav'lin, from the camp he took his way,
6150	Straight to the Grecian ships; but never thence
6150	Destin'd to bring th' expected tidings back.
6150	""
6150	The crowd of men and horses left behind,
6150	Briskly he mov'd along; Ulysses first
6150	Mark'd his approach, and to Tydides said:
6150	"""See, from the camp where some one this way comes,"
6150	With what intent I know not; if to play
6150	The spy about the ships, or rob the dead.
6150	Turn we aside, and let him pass us by
6150	A little way; we then with sudden rush
6150	May seize him; or if he outstrip us both
6150	By speed of foot, may urge him tow'rd the ships,
6150	Driving him still before us with our spears,
6150	"And from, the city cutting off his flight."""
6150	Thus saying, 'mid the dead, beside the road
6150	They crouch'd; he, all unconscious, hasten'd by.
6150	But when such space was interpos'd as leave
6150	Between the sluggish oxen and themselves                        [3]
6150	A team of mules (so much the faster they
6150	Through the stiff fallow drag the jointed plough),
6150	They rush'd upon him; at the sound he stopp'd,
6150	Deeming that from the Trojan camp they came,
6150	By Hector sent, to order his return.
6150	Within a spear's length when they came, or less,
6150	For foes he knew them, and to night address'd
6150	His active limbs; they rush'd in hot pursuit.
6150	And as two hounds, well practis'd in the chase,
6150	With glist'ning fangs, unflagging, strain to catch,
6150	In woodland glade, some pricket deer, or hare,
6150	That flies before them, screaming; so those two,
6150	Tydides and Ulysses, stout of heart,
6150	With fiery zeal, unflagging, strain'd to catch
6150	The flying Dolon, from the camp cut off;
6150	But when the fugitive approach'd the ships,
6150	Close by the guard, fresh vigour Pallas gave
6150	To Diomed, lest haply from the walls
6150	Some other might anticipate his blow,
6150	And he himself but second honours gain.
6150	Tydides then with threat'ning gesture cried,
6150	"""Stop, or I hurl my spear; and small thy chance,"
6150	"If I assail thee, of escape from death."""
6150	He said, and threw his spear; but by design
6150	It struck him not; above his shoulder flew
6150	The polish'd lance, and quiver'd in the ground.
6150	Sudden he stopp'd, with panic paralys'd:
6150	His teeth all chatt'ring, pale with fear he stood,
6150	With falt'ring accents; panting, they came up
6150	And seiz'd him in their grasp; he thus, in tears:
6150	"""Spare but my life; my life I can redeem;"
6150	For ample stores I have of gold, and brass,
6150	And well-wrought iron; and of these my sire
6150	Would pay a gen'rous ransom, could he learn
6150	"That in the Grecian ships I yet surviv'd."""
6150	""
6150	To whom Ulysses, deep-designing, thus:
6150	"""Be of good cheer; nor let the fear of death"
6150	Disturb thy mind; but tell me truly this;
6150	How is 't that tow'rd the ships thou com'st alone,
6150	In the still night, when other mortals sleep?
6150	Com'st thou perchance for plunder of the dead?
6150	Or seek'st upon our ships to play the spy,
6150	"By Hector sent? or of thine own accord?"""
6150	""
6150	Then Dolon thus--his knees with terror shook--
6150	"""With much persuasion, of my better mind"
6150	Hector beguil'd me, off'ring as my prize
6150	Achilles' horses and his brass-bound car;
6150	Through the dark night he sent me, and enjoin'd,
6150	Ent'ring your hostile camp, to learn if still
6150	Ye keep your wonted watch, or by our arms
6150	Subdued and vanquish'd, meditate retreat,
6150	"And worn with toil, your nightly watch neglect."""
6150	""
6150	To whom Ulysses thus with scornful smile:
6150	"""High soar'd thy hopes indeed, that thought to win"
6150	The horses of Achilles; hard are they
6150	For mortal man to harness or control,
6150	Save for Achilles' self, the Goddess-born.
6150	But tell me truly this; when here thou cam'st,
6150	Where left'st thou Hector, guardian chief of Troy?
6150	Where are his warlike arms? his horses where?
6150	Where lie the rest? and where are plac'd their guards?
6150	What are their secret counsels? do they mean
6150	Here by the ships to keep their ground, or back,
6150	"Sated with vict'ry, to the town return?"""
6150	""
6150	Whom Dolon answer'd thus, Eumedes' son:
6150	"""Thy questions all true answers shall receive;"
6150	Hector, with those who share his counsels, sits
6150	In conf'rence, far apart, near Ilus' tomb;
6150	But for the guards thou speak'st of, noble chief,
6150	Not one is station'd to protect the camp.
6150	Around the Trojan fires indeed, perforce,
6150	A watch is kept; and they, among themselves,
6150	Due caution exercise: but, for th' Allies,
6150	They sleep, and to the Trojans leave the watch,
6150	"Since nor their children nor their wives are near."""
6150	""
6150	To whom in answer sage Ulysses thus:
6150	"""Say now, where sleep they? with the Trojans mix'd,"
6150	"Or separate? explain, that I may know."""
6150	""
6150	Whom answer'd Dolon thus, Eumedes' son:
6150	"""To this too will I give ye answer true;"
6150	Next to the sea the Carian forces lie;
6150	The Paeon archers and the Leleges,
6150	The Caucons, and the bold Pelasgians next;
6150	On Thymbra's side the Lycians' lot has fall'n,
6150	The Mysians brave, the Phrygian cavalry,
6150	And the Maeonians with their horsehair plumes.
6150	But why of these enquire? if ye intend
6150	An inroad on the camp, apart from all,
6150	New come, the farthest off, the Thracians lie:
6150	Rhesus their King, the son of Eioneus,
6150	Sleeps in the midst; no steeds that e'er I saw
6150	For size and beauty can with his compare:
6150	Whiter than snow, and swifter than the wind.
6150	With gold and silver is his chariot wrought,
6150	His armour golden, of gigantic size,
6150	A marvel to behold! it seems not meet
6150	For mortal man, but for th' immortal Gods.
6150	But take me now in safety to the ships;
6150	Or leave me here in fetters bound, that so,
6150	Ere ye return, ye may approve my words,
6150	"And see if I have told you true, or no."""
6150	""
6150	To whom thus Diomed with stern regard:
6150	"""Dolon, though good thy tidings, hope not thou,"
6150	Once in our hands, to 'scape the doom of death;
6150	For if we now should let thee go, again
6150	In after times thou mightst our ships approach,
6150	As secret spy, or open enemy:
6150	But if beneath my hands thou lose thy life,
6150	"No farther trouble shalt thou cause the Greeks."""
6150	He said; and as the suppliant sought in vain
6150	To touch his beard, imploring, through his throat,
6150	Both tendons sev'ring, drove his trenchant blade:
6150	Ev'n while he spoke, his head was roll'd in dust.
6150	The cap of marten fur from off his head
6150	They took, the wolf-skin, and the bow unstrung,
6150	And jav'lin; these Ulysses held aloft,
6150	And thus to Pallas pray'd, who gave the spoil:
6150	"""Receive, great Goddess, these our gifts; to thee,"
6150	Of all th' Immortals on Olympus' height,
6150	Our off'rings first we give; conduct us now,
6150	"The Thracian camp and Thracian steeds to gain."""
6150	""
6150	Thus as he spoke, amid the tamarisk scrub
6150	Far off he threw the trophies; then with reeds,
6150	And twigs new broken from the tamarisk boughs,
6150	He set a mark, lest in the gloom of night
6150	Returning, they might haply miss the spot.
6150	Then on they pass'd thro' arms and blacken'd gore,
6150	And reach'd the confines of the Thracian camp.
6150	There found they all by sleep subdued; their arms
6150	Beside them on the ground, in order due,
6150	In triple rows; and by the side of each,
6150	Harness'd and yok'd, his horses ready stood.
6150	Surrounded by his warriors, Rhesus slept;
6150	Beside him stood his coursers fleet, their reins
6150	Suspended to the chariot's topmost rail:
6150	Ulysses mark'd him as he lay, and said,
6150	"""This is the man, Tydides, these the steeds,"
6150	To us by Dolon, whom we slew, describ'd.
6150	Now then, put forth thy might; beseems it not
6150	To stand thus idly with thine arms in hand:
6150	Loose thou the horses; or do thou the men
6150	"Despatch, and to my care the horses leave."""
6150	""
6150	He said: and Pallas vigour new inspir'd,
6150	That right and left he smote; dire were the groans
6150	Of slaughter'd men; the earth was red with blood;
6150	And as a lion on th' untended flock
6150	Of sheep or goats with savage onslaught springs,
6150	Ev'n so Tydides on the Thracians sprang,
6150	Till twelve were slain; and as Tydides' sword
6150	Gave each to death, Ulysses by the feet
6150	Drew each aside; reflecting, that perchance
6150	The horses, startled, might refuse to pass
6150	The corpses; for as yet they knew them not.
6150	But when Tydides saw the sleeping King,
6150	A thirteenth victim to his sword was giv'n,
6150	Painfully breathing; for by Pallas' art,
6150	He saw that night, as in an evil dream,
6150	The son of OEneus standing o'er his head.
6150	Meanwhile Ulysses sage the horses loos'd;
6150	He gather'd up the reins, and with his bow
6150	(For whip was none at hand) he drove them forth;
6150	Then softly whistling to Tydides gave
6150	A signal; he, the while, remain'd behind,
6150	Musing what bolder deed he yet might do;
6150	Whether the seat, whereon the arms were laid,
6150	To draw away, or, lifted high in air,
6150	To bear it off in triumph on the car;
6150	Or on the Thracians farther loss inflict;
6150	But while he mus'd, beside him Pallas stood,
6150	"And said, ""Bethink thee, Tydeus' son, betimes"
6150	Of thy return, lest, if some other God
6150	"Should wake the Trojans, thou shouldst need to fly."""
6150	""
6150	She said; the heav'nly voice he recogniz'd,
6150	And mounted straight the car; Ulysses touch'd
6150	The horses with his bow; and, urg'd to speed,
6150	They tow'rd the ships their rapid course pursued.
6150	""
6150	Nor idle watch Apollo kept, who saw
6150	Tydides o'er the plain by Pallas led;
6150	With anger fill'd, the Trojan camp he sought;
6150	And Rhesus' kinsman, good Hippocoon,
6150	The Thracian councillor, from sleep arous'd;
6150	Awaking, when the vacant space he view'd,
6150	Where late had stood the horses; and his friends
6150	Gasping in death, and welt'ring in their blood,
6150	He groan'd as on his comrade's name he call'd:
6150	Then loud the clamour rose, and wild uproar,
6150	Unspeakable, of Trojans thronging round;
6150	They marvell'd at the deeds; but marvell'd more
6150	How they who wrought them had escap'd unscath'd.
6150	""
6150	Meantime arriv'd where Hector's scout they slew,
6150	Ulysses, lov'd of Heav'n, a moment check'd
6150	His eager steeds; Tydides from the car
6150	Leap'd to the ground, and in Ulysses' hand
6150	The bloody trophies plac'd; then mounted quick,
6150	And tow'rd the ships, their destin'd goal, urg'd on
6150	The fiery horses; nothing loth, they flew.
6150	"Nestor first heard the sound, and cried, ""O friends,"
6150	The leaders and the councillors of Greece,
6150	Am I deceiv'd, or is it true? methinks
6150	The sound of horses, hurrying, strikes mine ear;
6150	Grant Heav'n, Ulysses and brave Diomed
6150	May bring those horses from the Trojan camp;
6150	Yet much I fear our bravest may have met
6150	"With some disaster 'mid the crowd of foes."""
6150	""
6150	He scarce had ended, when themselves appear'd,
6150	And from the car descended: welcom'd back
6150	With cordial grasp of hands, and friendly words.
6150	Gerenian Nestor first, enquiring, said:
6150	"""Tell me, renown'd Ulysses, pride of Greece,"
6150	Whence come these horses? from the Trojan camp?
6150	Or hath some God, that met you by the way,
6150	Bestow'd them, radiant as the beams of light?
6150	Among the Trojans day by day I move;
6150	'Tis not my wont; old warrior though I be,
6150	To lag behind; but horses such as these
6150	I never saw; some God hath giv'n them, sure;
6150	For Jove, the Cloud-compeller, loves you both,
6150	"And Pallas, child of aegis-bearing Jove."""
6150	""
6150	To whom again the sage Ulysses thus:
6150	"""O Nestor, son of Neleus, pride of Greece,"
6150	Had they so will'd, the Gods, so great their pow'r,
6150	E'en better horses could have giv'n than these;
6150	But these, old man, are Thracians, newly come;
6150	Whose King the valiant Diomed hath slain,
6150	And with him twelve, the best of all his band.
6150	A scout too have we slain, by Hector sent,
6150	"And by the Trojan chiefs, to spy our camp."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and o'er the ditch the horses drove,
6150	Exulting in their prize; and with him went
6150	The other chiefs, rejoicing, through the camp.
6150	Arriv'd at Diomed's well-order'd tent,
6150	First with strong halters to the rack, where stood,
6150	High-fed with corn, his own swift-footed steeds,
6150	The horses they secur'd; Ulysses then
6150	The bloody spoils of Dolon stow'd away
6150	In the ship's stern, till fitting sacrifice
6150	To Pallas might be offer'd; to the sea
6150	Descending then, they wash'd away the sweat,
6150	Which on their necks, and thighs, and knees had dried;
6150	The sweat wash'd off, and in the ocean waves
6150	Themselves refresh'd, they sought the polish'd bath;
6150	Then, by the bath restor'd, and all their limbs
6150	Anointed freely with the lissom oil,
6150	Sat down to breakfast; and from flowing bowls
6150	In Pallas' honour pour'd the luscious wine.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	ARGUMENT.
6150	""
6150	THE THIRD BATTLE, AND THE ACTS OF AGAMEMNON.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	Agamemnon, having armed himself, leads the Grecians to battle; Hector
6150	prepares the Trojans to receive them; while Jupiter, Juno, and Minerva,
6150	give the signals of war. Agamemnon bears all before him; and Hector is
6150	commanded by Jupiter (who sends Iris for that purpose) to decline the
6150	engagement, till the king should be wounded, and retire from the field.
6150	He then makes a great slaughter of the enemy; Ulysses and Diomed put a
6150	stop to him for a time; but the latter, being wounded by Paris, is
6150	obliged to desert his companion, who is encompassed by the Trojans,
6150	wounded, and in the utmost danger, till Menelaus and Ajax rescue him.
6150	Hector comes against Ajax, but that hero alone opposes multitudes and
6150	rallies the Greeks. In the meantime Machaon, in the other wing of the
6150	army, is pierced with an arrow by Paris, and carried from the fight in
6150	Nestor's chariot. Achilles (who overlooked the action from his ship)
6150	sends Patroclus to inquire which of the Greeks was wounded in that
6150	manner. Nestor entertains him in his tent with an account of the
6150	accidents of the day, and a long recital of some former wars which he
6150	had remembered, tending to put Patroclus upon persuading Achilles to
6150	fight for his countrymen, or at least to permit him to do it clad in
6150	Achilles' armour. Patroclus in his return meets Eurypylus also wounded,
6150	and assists in that distress.
6150	""
6150	This book opens with the eight-and-twentieth day of the poem; and the
6150	same day, with its various actions and adventures, is extended through
6150	the twelfth, thirteenth, fourteenth, fifteenth, sixteenth, seventeenth,
6150	and part of the eighteenth books. The scene lies in the field near the
6150	monument of Ilus.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	BOOK XI.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	Now rose Aurora from Tithonus' bed,
6150	To mortals and Immortals bringing light;
6150	When to the ships of Greece came Discord down,
6150	Despatch'd from Jove, with dire portents of war.
6150	Upon Ulysses' lofty ship she stood,
6150	The midmost, thence to shout to either side,
6150	Or to the tents of Ajax Telamon,
6150	Or of Achilles, who at each extreme,
6150	Confiding in their strength, had moor'd their ships.
6150	There stood the Goddess, and in accents loud
6150	And dread she call'd, and fix'd in ev'ry breast
6150	The fierce resolve to wage unwearied war;
6150	And dearer to their hearts than thoughts of home
6150	Or wish'd return, became the battle-field.
6150	""
6150	Atrides, loudly shouting, call'd the Greeks
6150	To arms: himself his flashing armour donn'd.
6150	First on his legs the well-wrought greaves he fix'd,
6150	Fasten'd with silver clasps; his ample chest
6150	A breastplate guarded, giv'n by Cinyras
6150	In pledge of friendship; for in Cyprus' isle
6150	He heard the rumour of the glorious fleet
6150	About to sail for Troy; and sought with gifts
6150	To win the favour of the mighty King.
6150	Ten bands were there inwrought of dusky bronze,
6150	Twelve of pure gold, twice ten of shining tin:
6150	Of bronze six dragons upwards tow'rds the neck
6150	Their length extended, three on either side:
6150	In colour like the bow, which Saturn's son
6150	Plac'd in the clouds, a sign to mortal men:
6150	Then o'er his shoulder threw his sword; bright flash'd
6150	The golden studs; the silver scabbard shone,
6150	With golden baldrick fitted; next his shield
6150	He took, full-siz'd, well-wrought, well-prov'd in fight;
6150	Around it ran ten circling rims of brass;
6150	With twenty bosses round of burnish'd tin,
6150	And, in the centre, one of dusky bronze.
6150	A Gorgon's head, with aspect terrible,
6150	Was wrought, with Fear and Flight encircled round:
6150	Depending from a silver belt it hung;
6150	And on the belt a dragon, wrought in bronze,
6150	Twin'd his lithe folds, and turn'd on ev'ry side,
6150	Sprung from a single neck, his triple head.
6150	Then on his brow his lofty helm he plac'd,
6150	Four-crested, double-peak'd, with horsehair plumes,
6150	That nodded,-fearful, from the warrior's head.
6150	Then took two weighty lances, tipp'd with brass,
6150	Which fiercely flash'd against the face of Heav'n:
6150	Pallas and Juno thund'ring from on high
6150	In honour of Mycenae's wealthy lord.
6150	""
6150	Forthwith they order'd, each his charioteer,
6150	To stay his car beside the ditch; themselves,
6150	On foot, in arms accoutred, sallied forth,
6150	And loud, ere early dawn, the clamour rose.
6150	Advanc'd before the cars, they lin'd the ditch;
6150	Follow'd the cars, a little space between:
6150	But Jove with dire confusion fill'd their ranks,
6150	Who sent from Heav'n a show'r of blood-stain'd rain.
6150	In sign of many a warrior's coming doom,
6150	Soon to the viewless shades untimely sent.
6150	Meanwhile upon the slope, beneath the plain,
6150	The Trojan chiefs were gather'd; Hector's self,
6150	Polydamas, AEneas, as a God
6150	In rev'rence held; Antenor's three brave sons,
6150	Agenor's godlike presence, Polybus,
6150	And, heav'nly fair, the youthful Acamas.
6150	In front was seen the broad circumference
6150	Of Hector's shield; and as amid the clouds
6150	Shines forth the fiery dog-star, bright and clear,
6150	Anon beneath the cloudy veil conceal'd;
6150	So now in front was Hector seen, and now
6150	Pass'd to the rear, exhorting; all in brass,
6150	His burnish'd arms like Jove's own lightning flash'd.
6150	""
6150	As in the corn-land of some wealthy Lord
6150	The rival bands of reapers mow the swathe,
6150	Barley or wheat; and fast the trusses fall;
6150	So Greeks and Trojans mow'd th' opposing ranks;
6150	Nor these admitted thought of faint retreat,
6150	But still made even head; while those, like wolves,
6150	Rush'd to the onset; Discord, Goddess dire,
6150	Beheld, rejoicing; of the heav'nly pow'rs
6150	She only mingled with the combatants;
6150	The others all were absent; they, serene,
6150	Repos'd in gorgeous palaces, for each
6150	Amid Olympus' deep recesses built.
6150	Yet all the cloud-girt son of Saturn blam'd,
6150	Who will'd the vict'ry to the arms of Troy.
6150	He heeded not their anger; but withdrawn
6150	Apart from all, in pride of conscious strength,
6150	Survey'd the walls of Troy, the ships of Greece,
6150	The flash of arms, the slayers and the slain.
6150	""
6150	While yet 'twas morn, and wax'd the youthful day,
6150	Thick flew the shafts, and fast the people fell
6150	On either side: but when the hour was come
6150	When woodmen, in the forest's deep recess,
6150	Prepare their food, and wearied with the toil
6150	Of felling loftiest trees, with aching arms
6150	Turn with keen relish to their midday meal;
6150	Then Grecian valour broke th' opposing ranks,
6150	As each along the line encourag'd each;
6150	First sprang the monarch Agamemnon forth,
6150	And brave Bienor slew, his people's guard;
6150	And, with the chief, his friend and charioteer,
6150	Oileus; he, down-leaping from the car,
6150	Stood forth defiant; but between his brows
6150	The monarch's spear was thrust; nor aught avail'd
6150	The brass-bound helm, to stay the weapon's point;
6150	Through helm and bone it pass'd, and all the brain
6150	Was shatter'd; forward as he rush'd, he fell.
6150	Them left he there, their bare breasts gleaming white,
6150	Stripp'd of their arms; and hasten'd in pursuit
6150	Of Antiphus and Isus, Priam's sons,
6150	A bastard one, and one legitimate,
6150	Both on one car; the bastard held the reins:
6150	Beside him stood the gallant Antiphus.
6150	Them, as they fed their flocks on Ida's heights,
6150	Achilles once had captive made, and bound
6150	With willow saplings, till for ransom freed.
6150	The mighty monarch, Agamemnon, drove
6150	Through Isus' breast his spear; his weighty sword
6150	Descended on the head of Antiphus
6150	Beside the ear, and hurl'd him from his car;
6150	These of their armour he despoil'd in haste,
6150	Known to him both; for he had seen them oft
6150	Beside the ships, when thither captive brought
6150	From Ida by Achilles, swift of foot.
6150	As when a lion in their lair hath seiz'd
6150	The helpless offspring of a mountain doe,
6150	And breaks their bones with ease, and with strong teeth
6150	Crushes their tender life; nor can their dam,
6150	Though close at hand she be, avail them aught;
6150	For she herself by deadly terror seiz'd,
6150	Through the thick coppice and the forest flies,
6150	Panting, and bath'd in sweat, the monster's rush;
6150	So dar'd no Trojan give those brethren aid,
6150	Themselves in terror of the warlike Greeks.
6150	Peisander next, and bold Hippolochus,
6150	Sons of Antimachus ('twas he who chief,
6150	Seduc'd by Paris' gold and splendid gifts,
6150	Advis'd the restitution to refuse
6150	Of Helen to her Lord), the King assail'd;
6150	Both on one car; but from their hands had dropp'd
6150	The broider'd reins; bewilder'd there they stood;
6150	While, with a lion's bound, upon them sprang
6150	The son of Atreus; suppliant, in the car,
6150	"They clasp'd his knees; ""Give quarter, Atreus' son,"
6150	Redeem our lives; our sire Antimachus
6150	Possesses goodly store of brass and gold,
6150	And well-wrought iron; and of these he fain
6150	Would pay a noble ransom, could he hear
6150	"That in the Grecian ships we yet surviv'd."""
6150	""
6150	Thus they, with gentle words, and tears, imploring;
6150	But all ungentle was the voice they heard
6150	"In answer; ""If indeed ye be the sons"
6150	Of that Antimachus, who counsel gave,
6150	When noble Menelaus came to Troy
6150	With sage Ulysses, as ambassadors,
6150	To slay them both, nor suffer their return,
6150	"Pay now the forfeit of your father's guilt."""
6150	He said, and with a spear-thrust through his breast
6150	Peisander dash'd to earth; backward he fell.
6150	Down leap'd Hippolochus; but Atreus' son
6150	Severing his hands and neck, amid the throng
6150	Sent whirling like a bowl the gory head.
6150	These left he there; and where the thickest throng
6150	Maintain'd the tug of war, thither he flew,
6150	And with him eager hosts of well-greav'd Greeks.
6150	Soon on the Trojans' flight enforc'd they hung,
6150	Destroying; foot on foot, and horse on horse;
6150	While from the plain thick clouds of dust arose
6150	Beneath the armed hoofs of clatt'ring steeds;
6150	And on the monarch Agamemnon press'd,
6150	Still slaying, urging still the Greeks to arms.
6150	As when amid a densely timber'd wood
6150	Light the devouring flames, by eddying winds
6150	Hither and thither borne, fast falls the copse
6150	Prostrate beneath the fire's impetuous course;
6150	So thickly fell the flying Trojans' heads
6150	Beneath the might of Agamemnon's arm;
