6150	And here and there, athwart the pass of war,
6150	Was many an empty car at random whirl'd
6150	By strong-neck'd steeds, of guiding hands bereft;
6150	Stretch'd on the plain they lay, more welcome sight
6150	To carrion birds than to their widow'd wives.
6150	But Hector, from the fray and din of war,
6150	And dust, and blood, and carnage, Jove withdrew.
6150	Still on Atrides press'd, the Greek pursuit
6150	With eager shouts exciting; past the tomb
6150	Of Ilus, ancient son of Dardanus,
6150	And tow'rd the fig-tree, midway o'er the plain,
6150	Straining to gain the town, the Trojans fled;
6150	While loudly shouting, his unconquer'd hands
6150	With carnage dyed, Atrides urg'd their flight.
6150	But when the Scaean gates and oak were reach'd,
6150	They made a stand, and fac'd the foe's assault.
6150	Some o'er the open plain were yet dispers'd;
6150	As heifers, by a lion scatter'd wide,
6150	At dead of night; all fly; on one descends
6150	The doom of death; her with his pow'rful teeth
6150	He seizes, and, her neck first broken, rends,
6150	And on her entrails gorging, laps her blood.
6150	So these the monarch Agamemnon chas'd,
6150	Slaying the hindmost; they in terror fled:
6150	Some headlong, backward some, Atrides' hand
6150	Hurl'd from their chariot many a warrior bold;
6150	So forward and so fierce he bore his spear.
6150	But as he near'd the city, and stood beneath
6150	The lofty wall, the Sire of Gods and men
6150	From Heav'n descended; on the topmost height
6150	Of Ida's spring-abounding hill he sat:
6150	And while his hand the lightning grasp'd, he thus
6150	To golden-winged Iris gave command:
6150	""
6150	"""Haste thee, swift Iris, and to Hector bear"
6150	From me this message; bid him, that as long
6150	As Agamemnon in the van appears,
6150	Raging, and dealing death among the ranks,
6150	He from the battle keep himself aloof,
6150	But urge the rest undaunted to maintain
6150	The stubborn fight; but should Atrides, struck
6150	By spear or arrow, to his car withdraw,
6150	He shall from me receive such pow'r to slay,
6150	As to the ships shall bear him, ere the sun
6150	"Decline, and Darkness spread her hallowing shade."""
6150	""
6150	Thus he; to Troy, obedient to his word,
6150	From Ida's heights swift-footed Iris sped:
6150	Amid the horses and the well-fram'd cars
6150	The godlike Hector, Priam's son, she found,
6150	And stood beside him, and address'd him thus:
6150	""
6150	"""Hector, thou son of Priam, sage as Jove"
6150	In council, he the Universal Lord
6150	Sends thee by me this message; that as long
6150	As Agamemnon in the van appears,
6150	Raging, and dealing death amid the ranks,
6150	Thou from the battle keep thyself aloof,
6150	But urge the rest undaunted to maintain
6150	The stubborn fight; but should Atrides, struck
6150	By spear or arrow, to his car withdraw,
6150	Thou shalt from him receive such pow'r to slay
6150	As to the ships shall bear thee, ere the sun
6150	"Decline, and Darkness spread her hallowing shade."""
6150	""
6150	Swift-footed Iris said, and disappear'd;
6150	But from his chariot Hector leap'd to earth,
6150	Hither and thither passing through the ranks,
6150	With brandish'd jav'lins urging to the fight.
6150	Loud, at his bidding, rose the battle-cry;
6150	Back roll'd the tide; again they fac'd the Greeks:
6150	On th' other side the Greeks their masses form'd,
6150	In line of battle rang'd; opposed they stood;
6150	And in the front, to none content to cede
6150	The foremost place, was Agamemnon seen.
6150	""
6150	Say now, ye Nine, who on Olympus dwell,
6150	Of all the Trojans and their fam'd Allies,
6150	Who first oppos'd to Agamemnon stood.
6150	Iphidamas, Antenor's gallant son,
6150	Stalwart and brave; in fertile Thracia bred,
6150	Mother of flocks; him, in his infant years,
6150	His grandsire Cisseus, fair Theano's sire,
6150	In his own palace rear'd; and when he reach'd
6150	The perfect measure of his glorious youth,
6150	Still in his house retain'd him, and to wife
6150	Gave him his daughter; but when tidings came
6150	Of Grecian warfare, from the marriage straight
6150	Embarking, with twelve beaked ships he sailed,
6150	That owned his sway; these on Percote's shore
6150	He left; and came himself on foot to Troy;
6150	Who now confronted Atreus' godlike son.
6150	""
6150	When near they drew, Atrides miss'd his aim,
6150	His spear diverging; then Iphidamas
6150	Beneath the breastplate, striking on his belt,
6150	Strove with strong hand to drive the weapon home:
6150	Yet could not pierce the belt's close-plaited work;
6150	The point, encounter'd by the silver fold,
6150	Was bent, like lead; then with his pow'rful hand
6150	The monarch Agamemnon seiz'd the spear,
6150	And tow'rd him drew, and with a lion's strength
6150	Wrench'd from his foeman's grasp; then on his neck
6150	Let fall his sword, and slack'd his limbs in death.
6150	There, falling in his country's cause, he slept
6150	The iron sleep of death; unhappy he,
6150	Far from his virgin-bride, yet unpossess'd,
6150	Though bought with costly presents; first he gave
6150	A hundred steers; and promis'd thousands more
6150	Of sheep and goats from out his countless flocks.
6150	Him Agamemnon of his arms despoil'd,
6150	And to the crowd of Greeks the trophies bore.
6150	But when Antenor's eldest-born beheld,
6150	Coon, th' observ'd of all men, bitt'rest grief
6150	His eyes o'ershadow'd, for his brother's fate;
6150	And, unperceiv'd by Atreus' godlike son,
6150	Standing aside, he struck him with his spear,
6150	Through the mid arm, beneath the elbow's bend;
6150	And drove right through the weapon's glitt'ring point.
6150	Writh'd with the pain the mighty King of men;
6150	Yet from the combat flinch'd he not, nor quail'd:
6150	But grasping firm his weather-toughen'd spear
6150	On Coon rush'd, as by the feet he drew
6150	His father's son, Iphidamas, away,
6150	Invoking all the bravest to his aid;
6150	And as he drew the body tow'rd the crowd,
6150	Beneath the bossy shield the monarch thrust
6150	His brass-clad spear, and slack'd his limbs in death;
6150	Then near approaching, ev'n upon the corpse
6150	Of dead Iphidamas, struck off his head:
6150	So by Atrides' hand, Antenor's sons,
6150	Their doom accomplish'd, to the shades were sent.
6150	Then through the crowded ranks, with spear and sword,
6150	And massive stones, he held his furious course,
6150	While the hot blood was welling from his arm;
6150	But when the wound was dry, and stanch'd the blood,
6150	Keen anguish then Atrides' might subdued.
6150	As when a woman in her labour-throes
6150	Sharp pangs encompass, by Lucina sent,
6150	Who rules o'er child-birth travail, ev'n so keen
6150	The pangs that then Atrides' might subdued.
6150	Mounting his car he bade his charioteer
6150	Drive to the ships; for sore his spirit was pain'd;
6150	But loud and clear he shouted to the Greeks:
6150	"""O friends, the chiefs and councillors of Greece,"
6150	Yours be it now our sea-borne ships to guard:
6150	Since Jove, the Lord of counsel, through the day
6150	"Wills not that I the battle should maintain."""
6150	""
6150	He said: and swiftly to the ships were driv'n
6150	His sleek-skinn'd coursers; nothing loth they flew;
6150	With foam their chests were fleck'd, with dust their flanks,
6150	As from the field their wounded Lord they bore:
6150	But Hector, as he saw the King retire,
6150	To Trojans and to Lycians call'd aloud:
6150	""
6150	"""Trojans and Lycians, and ye Dardans fam'd"
6150	In close encounter, quit ye now like men;
6150	Put forth your wonted valour; from the field
6150	Their bravest has withdrawn, and Jove on me
6150	Great glory hath shed; now headlong on the Greeks
6150	"Urge your swift steeds, and endless honour gain."""
6150	""
6150	His words fresh courage rous'd in ev'ry breast:
6150	And as a hunter cheers his sharp-fang'd hounds
6150	On forest boar or lion; on the Greeks
6150	So cheer'd the valiant Trojans Priam's son,
6150	Illustrious Hector, stern as blood-stain'd Mars.
6150	Bent on high deeds, himself in front advanc'd,
6150	Fell on the masses as a whirlwind falls,
6150	Lashing with furious sweep the dark-blue sea.
6150	""
6150	Say then, who first, who last, by Hector's hand,
6150	Whom Jove had will'd to crown with honour, died.
6150	Assaeus first, and then Autonous,
6150	Opites, and Opheltius, Dolops, son
6150	Of Clytus, and AEsumnus, Agelas
6150	And Orus, and brave Hipponous;
6150	All these the chiefs of Greece; the nameless crowd
6150	He scatter'd next; as when the west wind drives
6150	The clouds, and battles with the hurricane,
6150	Before the clearing blast of Notus driv'n;
6150	The big waves heave and roll, and high aloft,
6150	The gale, careering, flings the ocean spray;
6150	So thick and furious fell on hostile heads
6150	The might of Hector. Now had fearful deeds
6150	Been done, and Greeks beside their ships had fall'n
6150	In shameful rout, had not Ulysses thus
6150	To Diomed, the son of Tydeus, call'd:
6150	""
6150	"""Why, son of Tydeus, should we thus relax"
6150	Our warlike courage? come, stand by me now,
6150	True friend! if Hector of the glancing helm
6150	"Our ships should capture, great were our disgrace."""
6150	""
6150	Whom answer'd thus the valiant Diomed:
6150	"""Beside thee will I stand, and still endure;"
6150	But brief will be the term of our success,
6150	Since Jove, the Cloud-compeller, not to us,
6150	"But to the Trojans, wills the victory."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and from his car Thymbraeus hurl'd,
6150	Through the left breast transfix'd: Ulysses' hand
6150	His charioteer, the brave Molion, slew.
6150	These left they there, no more to share the fight;
6150	Then turning, spread confusion 'mid the crowd:
6150	As turn two boars upon the hunter's pack
6150	With desp'rate courage, turning so to bay,
6150	Those two, the Trojans scatt'ring, gave the Greeks,
6150	From Hector flying, time again to breathe.
6150	A car they seiz'd which bore two valiant chiefs,
6150	Sons of Percotian Merops; he, o'er all
6150	In lore prophetic skill'd, would fain at home
6150	Have kept them from the life-destroying war:
6150	But they, by adverse fate impell'd to seek
6150	Their doom of death, his warning voice despis'd.
6150	These two, of strength and life at once bereft,
6150	The son of Tydeus, valiant Diomed,
6150	Stripp'd of their armour; while Ulysses slew
6150	Hippodamus, and bold Hyperochus.
6150	Thus Jove, from Ida's height beholding, held
6150	His even scale, each party slaught'ring each.
6150	Then with his spear Tydides through the loins
6150	Agastrophus, the son of Paeon, smote;
6150	No car had he at hand, whereto to fly:
6150	But, ill-advis'd, had in th' attendants' charge
6150	His horses left far off; while he himself
6150	Rush'd 'mid the throng on foot, and met his doom.
6150	Hector's quick glance athwart the files beheld,
6150	And to the rescue, with a shout, he sprang,
6150	The Trojan columns following; not unmov'd
6150	The valiant Diomed his coming saw,
6150	And thus bespoke Ulysses at his side:
6150	"""On us this plague, this mighty Hector, falls:"
6150	"Yet stand we firm, and boldly meet the shock."""
6150	He said, and, poising, hurl'd his pond'rous spear,
6150	And not in vain; on Hector's head it struck
6150	His helmet's crest, but, brass encount'ring brass,
6150	Himself it reach'd not; for the visor'd helm,
6150	Apollo's gift, three-plated, stay'd its force.
6150	Yet backward Hector sprang amid the crowd,
6150	And on his knees he dropp'd, his stalwart hand
6150	Propp'd on the ground; while darkness veil'd his eyes.
6150	But ere Tydides, following up his spear,
6150	Attain'd from far the spot whereon he fell,
6150	Hector reviv'd, and mounting quick his car,
6150	Drove 'mid the crowd, and 'scap'd the doom of death
6150	Then thus, with threat'ning spear, Tydides cried:
6150	"""Yet once again, vile hound, hast thou escap'd;"
6150	Thy doom was nigh; but thee thy God hath sav'd,
6150	Phoebus, to whom, amid the clash of spears,
6150	Well mayst thou pray! We yet shall meet again;
6150	When I shall end thee, if a guardian God
6150	I too may claim; meanwhile from thee I turn,
6150	"And others seek on whom my hap may light."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and turn'd him of his arms to strip
6150	The son of Paeon; but beside the stone
6150	That mark'd where men of old had rais'd a mound
6150	To Ilus, Dardan's son, the ancient chief,
6150	There crouching, Paris, fair-hair'd Helen's Lord,
6150	Against the son of Tydeus bent his bow.
6150	He from the breast of brave Agastrophus
6150	Had stripp'd the corslet; from his shoulders broad
6150	The buckler, and the helmet from his head,
6150	When Paris bent his bow, and not in vain
6150	His arrow launch'd; Tydides' dexter foot
6150	Right through it pierc'd, and pinn'd it to the ground.
6150	Joyous he laugh'd, and from his hiding place
6150	Sprang forth, and thus in tones of triumph cried:
6150	""
6150	"""Thou hast it! not in vain my shaft hath flown!"
6150	Would that, deep buried in thy flank, it touch'd
6150	Thy very life! so should our Trojans lose
6150	Their panic fear, who now on thee with dread,
6150	"As bleating goats upon a lion, look."""
6150	""
6150	To whom, unmov'd, the valiant Diomed:
6150	"""Poor archer, trusting to thy bow alone,"
6150	Vile sland'rer and seducer! if indeed
6150	Thou durst in arms oppos'd to me to stand,
6150	Nought would avail thy arrows and thy bow:
6150	And now, because thy shaft hath graz'd my foot,
6150	Thou mak'st thine empty boast: I heed thee not,
6150	More than a woman or a puny child:
6150	A worthless coward's weapon hath no point.
6150	'Tis diff'rent far with me! though light it fall,
6150	My spear is sharp, and whom it strikes, it slays.
6150	His widow's cheeks are mark'd with scars of grief,
6150	His children orphans; rotting on the ground,
6150	Red with his blood, he lies, his fun'ral rites
6150	"By carrion birds, and not by women paid."""
6150	""
6150	Thus while he spoke, Ulysses, spearman bold,
6150	Drew near, and stood before him; he, behind,
6150	Sat down protected, and from out his foot
6150	The arrow drew; whereat sharp anguish shot
6150	Through all his flesh; and mounting on his car
6150	He bade his faithful charioteer in haste
6150	Drive to the ships, for pain weigh'd down his soul.
6150	Alone Ulysses stood; of all the Greeks
6150	Not one beside him; all were panic-struck:
6150	Then with his spirit, perturb'd, he commun'd thus:
6150	"""Me miserable!  which way shall I choose?"
6150	'Twere ill indeed that I should turn to flight
6150	By hostile numbers daunted; yet 'twere worse
6150	Here to be caught alone; and Saturn's son
6150	With panic fear the other Greeks hath fill'd.
6150	Yet why, my soul, admit such thoughts as these?
6150	I know that cowards from the battle fly;
6150	But he who boasts a warrior's name, must learn,
6150	"Wounded or wounding, firmly still to stand."""
6150	""
6150	While in his mind and spirit thus he mus'd,
6150	Onward the buckler'd ranks of Trojans came,
6150	And, to their harm, encircled him around.
6150	As when a boar, by dogs and stalwart youths
6150	Attack'd, the shelt'ring thicket leaves, and whets
6150	The tusks that gleam between his curved jaws;
6150	They crowd around, though ring his clatt'ring tusks,
6150	And, fearful though it be, await his rush:
6150	So crowded round Ulysses, dear to Jove,
6150	The Trojans; he, with brandish'd spear aloft,
6150	Sprang forth, and through the shoulder, from above,
6150	Deiopites wounded: Thoon next
6150	He slew, and Ennomus; then with his spear
6150	Chersidamas, in act to quit his car,
6150	Thrust through the loins below his bossy shield:
6150	Prone in the dust, he clutch'd the blood-stain'd soil.
6150	From these he turn'd; and wounded with his spear
6150	Charops, the high-born Socus' brother, son
6150	Of Hippasus; then forward sprang, to aid
6150	His brother, godlike Socus; close he stood
6150	Before Ulysses, and address'd him thus:
6150	"""Far-fam'd Ulysses, as in arms, in wiles"
6150	Unwearied, thou this day o'er both the sons
6150	Of Hippasus, two mighty warriors slain,
6150	And of their armour spoil'd, shalt make thy boast,
6150	"Or by my spear thyself shalt lose thy life."""
6150	He said, and on the shield's broad circle struck:
6150	Through the bright shield the sturdy weapon drove,
6150	And through the rich-wrought baldrick, from the ribs
6150	Tearing the flesh away; but Pallas seiz'd,
6150	And turn'd it from the vital parts aside.
6150	The wound, Ulysses knew, was not to death,
6150	And back he drew, and thus to Socus cried:
6150	""
6150	"""Ill-fated thou! thy doom hath found thee now;"
6150	Me hast thou hinder'd from the war awhile;
6150	But thee to swift destruction and dark death,
6150	This day I doom: great glory, of thee subdued,
6150	"Shall I obtain, and Hades take thy soul."""
6150	""
6150	Thus he: and Socus, turning, sought to fly;
6150	But as he turn'd him round, Ulysses' spear
6150	Behind his neck, between the shoulder blades
6150	Was driv'n, and through his chest; thund'ring he fell,
6150	And o'er his fall Ulysses, vaunting, thus:
6150	""
6150	"""Socus, thou son of warlike Hippasus,"
6150	Here hast thou found, nor couldst escape, thy doom.
6150	Ill-fated thou! nor sire's nor mother's hand
6150	Shall gather up thy bones, but carrion birds
6150	O'er thee shall flap their baleful wings, and tear
6150	Thy mangled flesh; for me, whene'er I die
6150	"The sons of Greece will build my fun'ral pile."""
6150	From out his flesh, and from the bossy shield,
6150	The spear of Socus, as he spoke, he drew;
6150	And as he drew it forth, out gush'd his blood,
6150	With anguish keen. The Trojans, when they saw
6150	Ulysses' blood, with clam'rous shouts advanc'd
6150	Promiscuous; he, retiring, shouted loud
6150	To call his comrades; loud as head of man
6150	Could bear, he shouted thrice; and thrice his shout
6150	The warlike Menelaus heard, and thus
6150	To Ajax, standing by his side, he spoke:
6150	""
6150	"""Ajax, thou Heav'n-born son of Telamon,"
6150	Great chief of men, methinks I hear the voice
6150	Of stout Ulysses, as though left alone,
6150	And in the stubborn fight cut off from aid,
6150	By Trojans overmaster'd. Haste we then,
6150	For so 'twere best, to give him present aid.
6150	Brave though he be, yet left alone, I fear
6150	"Great cause we Greeks may have to mourn his loss."""
6150	""
6150	He spoke, and led the way; the godlike chief
6150	Follow'd his steps: Ulysses, dear to Jove,
6150	Surrounded by the Trojan host they found,
6150	As hungry jackals on the mountain side
6150	Around a stag, that from an archer's hand
6150	Hath taken hurt, yet while his blood was warm
6150	And limbs yet serv'd, has baffled his pursuit;
6150	But when the fatal shaft has drain'd his strength,
6150	Thirsting for blood, beneath the forest shade,
6150	The jackals seize their victim; then if chance
6150	A hungry lion pass, the jackals shrink
6150	In terror back, while he devours the prey;
6150	So round Ulysses, sage in council, press'd
6150	The Trojans, many and brave, yet nobly he
6150	Averted, spear in hand, the fatal hour;
6150	Till, with his tow'r-like shield before him borne,
6150	Appear'd great Ajax, and beside him stood.
6150	Hither and thither then the Trojans fled;
6150	While with supporting arm from out the crowd
6150	The warlike Menelaus led him forth,
6150	Till his attendant with his car drew near.
6150	Then Ajax, on the Trojans springing, slew
6150	Doryclus, royal Priam's bastard son;
6150	Next Pyrasus he smote, and Pandocus,
6150	Lysander, and Pylartes; as a stream,
6150	Swoll'n by the rains of Heav'n, that from the hills
6150	Pours down its wintry torrent on the plain;
6150	And many a blighted oak, and many a pine
6150	It bears, with piles of drift-wood, to the sea

6150	O'erthrowing men and horses; though unknown
6150	To Hector; he, upon Scamander's banks

6150	Where round great Nestor and the warlike King
6150	Idomeneus, while men were falling fast,
6150	Rose, irrepressible, the battle cry.
6150	Hector, 'mid these, was working wondrous deeds,
6150	With spear and car, routing th' opposed youth;
6150	Yet had the Greeks ev'n so their ground maintain'd,
6150	But godlike Paris, fair-hair'd Helen's Lord,
6150	Through the right shoulder, with a three-barb'd shaft,

6150	For him the warrior Greeks were sore afraid
6150	Lest he, as back the line of battle roll'd,
6150	Might to the foe be left; to Nestor then
6150	Idomeneus address'd his speech, and said:
6150	""
6150	"""O Nestor, son of Neleus, pride of Greece,"
6150	Haste thee to mount thy car, and with thee take
6150	Machaon; tow'rd the vessels urge with speed

6150	The skilful leech, who knows, with practis'd hand,
6150	"T' extract the shaft, and healing drugs apply."""
6150	""
6150	He said: Gerenian Nestor at the word
6150	Mounted his car, Machaon at his side,
6150	The skilful leech, sage AEsculapius' son:
6150	He touch'd his horses; tow'rd the Grecian ships,
6150	As was his purpose, nothing loth, they flew.
6150	""
6150	To Hector then Cebriones, who saw
6150	Confus'd the Trojans' right, drew near, and said:
6150	""
6150	"""Hector, we here, on th' outskirts of the field,"
6150	O'erpow'r the Greeks; on th' other side, our friends
6150	In strange confusion mingled, horse and man,

6150	The son of Telamon; I know him well,
6150	And the broad shield that o'er his shoulders hangs;
6150	Thither direct we then our car, where most
6150	In mutual slaughter horse and foot engage,
6150	"And loudest swells, uncheck'd, the battle cry."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and with the pliant lash he touch'd
6150	The sleek-skinn'd horses; springing at the sound,
6150	Between the Greeks and Trojans, light they bore
6150	The flying car, o'er bodies of the slain
6150	And broken bucklers trampling; all beneath
6150	Was plash'd with blood the axle, and the rails

6150	And from the felloes of the wheels, were thrown

6150	The strife of men, and break th' opposing ranks.
6150	His coming spread confusion 'mid the Greeks,
6150	His spear awhile withheld; then through the rest,
6150	With sword, and spear, and pond'rous stones he rush'd,
6150	But shunn'd the might of Ajax Telamon.
6150	""
6150	But Jove, high thron'd, the soul of Ajax fill'd
6150	With fear; aghast he stood; his sev'nfold shield
6150	He threw behind his back, and, trembling, gaz'd
6150	Upon the crowd; then, like some beast of prey,
6150	Foot slowly following foot, reluctant turn'd.
6150	As when the rustic youths and dogs have driv'n
6150	A tawny lion from the cattle fold,
6150	Watching all night, and baulk'd him of his prey;
6150	Rav'ning for flesh, he still th' attempt renews,
6150	But still in vain: for many a jav'lin, hurl'd
6150	By vig'rous arms, confronts him to his face,
6150	And blazing faggots, that his courage daunt;
6150	Till, with the dawn, reluctant he retreat:
6150	So from before the Trojans Ajax turn'd,
6150	Reluctant, fearing for the ships of Greece.
6150	As near a field of corn, a stubborn ass,
6150	Upon whose sides had many a club been broke,
6150	O'erpow'rs his boyish guides, and ent'ring in,
6150	On the rich forage grazes; while the boys
6150	Their cudgels ply, but vain their puny strength,
6150	Yet drive him out, when fully fed, with ease:
6150	Ev'n so great Ajax, son of Telamon,
6150	The valiant Trojans and their fam'd Allies,
6150	Still thrusting at his shield, before them drove:
6150	Yet would he sometimes, rallying, hold in check
6150	The Trojan host; then turn again to flight,
6150	Yet barring still the passage to the ships.
6150	Midway between the Trojans and the Greeks
6150	He stood defiant; many jav'lins, hurl'd
6150	By vig'rous arms, were in their flight receiv'd
6150	On his broad shield; and many, ere they reach'd
6150	Their living mark, fell midway on the plain,
6150	Fix'd in the ground, in vain athirst for blood.
6150	Him thus, hard press'd by thick-thrown spears, beheld

6150	He hasten'd up, and aim'd his glitt'ring spear;
6150	And Apisaon, Phausias' noble son,
6150	Below the midriff through the liver struck,

6150	Forth sprang Eurypylus to seize the spoils:
6150	But godlike Paris saw, and as he stoop'd

6150	Against Eurypylus he bent his bow,

6150	Disabling, in the wound the arrow broke.
6150	He 'mid his friends, escaping death, withdrew,
6150	And to the Greeks with piercing shout he call'd:
6150	""
6150	"""O friends, the chiefs and councillors of Greece,"
6150	Turn yet again, and from the doom of death
6150	Great Ajax save, hard press'd by hostile spears:
6150	Scarce can I hope he may escape with life
6150	The desp'rate fight; yet bravely stand, and aid
6150	"The mighty Ajax, son of Telamon."""
6150	""
6150	Thus spoke the wounded hero: round him they
6150	With sloping shields and spears uplifted stood:
6150	Ajax to meet them came; and when he reach'd
6150	The friendly ranks, again he turn'd to bay.
6150	So rag'd, like blazing fire, the furious fight.
6150	""
6150	Meanwhile the mares of Neleus, drench'd with sweat,
6150	Bore Nestor and Machaon from the field;
6150	Achilles saw, and mark'd them where he stood

6150	(That day commenc'd his evil destiny)
6150	And thus Menoetius' noble son began:
6150	""
6150	"""Why call'st thou me? what wouldst thou, Peleus' son?"""
6150	To whom Achilles, swift of foot, replied:
6150	"""Son of Menoetius, dearest to my soul,"
6150	Soon, must the suppliant Greeks before me kneel,
6150	So insupportable is now their need.
6150	But haste thee now, Patroclus, dear to Jove:
6150	Enquire of Nestor, from the battle field
6150	Whom brings he wounded: looking from behind
6150	Most like he seem'd to AEsculapius' son,
6150	Machaon; but his face I could not see,
6150	"So swiftly past the eager horses flew."""
6150	""
6150	He said: obedient to his friend's command,
6150	Quick to the tents and ships Patroclus ran.
6150	""
6150	They, when they reach'd the tent of Neleus' son,
6150	Descended to the ground; Eurymedon
6150	The old man's mares unharness'd from the car,
6150	While on the beach they fac'd the cooling breeze,
6150	Which from their garments dried the sweat; then turn'd,
6150	And in the tent on easy seats repos'd.
6150	For them the fair-hair'd Hecamede mix'd
6150	A cordial potion; her from Tenedos,
6150	When by Achilles ta'en, the old man brought;
6150	Daughter of great Arsinous, whom the Greeks
6150	On him, their sagest councillor, bestow'd.
6150	Before them first a table fair she spread,
6150	Well polish'd, and with feet of solid bronze;
6150	On this a brazen canister she plac'd,
6150	And onions, as a relish to the wine,
6150	And pale clear honey, and pure barley meal:
6150	By these a splendid goblet, which from home
6150	Th' old man had brought, with golden studs adorn'd:
6150	Four were its handles, and round each two doves
6150	Appear'd to feed; at either end, a cup.
6150	Scarce might another move it from the board,
6150	When full; but aged Nestor rais'd with ease.
6150	In this, their goddess-like attendant first
6150	A gen'rous measure mix'd of Pramnian wine:
6150	Then with a brazen grater shredded o'er
6150	The goatsmilk cheese, and whitest barley meal,
6150	And of the draught compounded bade them drink.
6150	They drank, and then, reliev'd the parching thirst,
6150	With mutual converse entertain'd the hour.
6150	Before the gate divine Patroclus stood:
6150	The old man saw, and from his seat arose,
6150	And took him by the hand, and led him in,
6150	And bade him sit; but he, refusing, said:
6150	""
6150	"""No seat for me, thou venerable sire!"
6150	I must not stay; for he both awe and fear
6150	Commands, who hither sent me to enquire
6150	What wounded man thou hast; I need not ask,
6150	I know Machaon well, his people's guard.
6150	My errand done, I must my message bear
6150	Back to Achilles; and thou know'st thyself,
6150	Thou venerable sire, how stern his mood:
6150	"Nay sometimes blames he, where no blame is due."""
6150	""
6150	To whom Gerenian Nestor thus replied:
6150	"""Whence comes Achilles' pity for the Greeks"
6150	By Trojan weapons wounded? knows he not
6150	What depth of suff'ring through the camp prevails?
6150	How in the ships, by arrow or by spear
6150	Sore wounded, all our best and bravest lie?
6150	The valiant son of Tydeus, Diomed,
6150	Pierc'd by a shaft; Ulysses by a spear,
6150	And Agamemnon's self; Eurypylus
6150	By a sharp arrow through the thigh transfix'd;
6150	And here another, whom but now I bring,
6150	Shot by a bow, from off the battle field:
6150	Achilles, valiant as he is, the while
6150	For Grecian woes nor care nor pity feels.
6150	Waits he, until our ships beside the sea,
6150	In our despite, are burnt by hostile fires,
6150	And we be singly slain? not mine is now
6150	The strength I boasted once of active limbs.
6150	O that such youth and vigour yet were mine,
6150	As when about a cattle-lifting raid
6150	We fought th' Eleans; there Itymoneus
6150	I slew, the son of brave Hyperochus,
6150	Who dwelt in Elis; and my booty drove.
6150	He sought to guard the herd; but from my hand
6150	A jav'lin struck him in the foremost ranks:
6150	He fell, and terror seiz'd the rustic crowd.
6150	Abundant store of plunder from the plain
6150	We drove: of horned cattle fifty herds;
6150	As many flocks of sheep, as many droves
6150	Of swine, as many wide-spread herds of goats,
6150	And thrice so many golden-chesnut mares,
6150	The foals of many running with their dams.
6150	To Pylos, Neleus' city, these we drove
6150	By night; and much it gladden'd Neleus' heart,
6150	That I, though new to war, such prize had won.
6150	When morn appear'd, the clear-voic'd heralds call'd
6150	For all to whom from Elis debts were due;
6150	Collected thus, the Pylians' leading men
6150	Division made: for Elis ow'd us much;
6150	Such wrongs we few in Pylos had sustain'd.
6150	The might of Hercules in former years
6150	Had storm'd our town, and all our bravest slain.
6150	Twelve gallant sons had Neleus; I of these
6150	Alone was left; the others all were gone.
6150	Whence over-proud, th' Epeians treated us
6150	With insult, and high-handed violence.
6150	A herd of oxen now, and num'rous flock
6150	Of sheep, th' old man selected for himself,
6150	Three hundred, with their shepherds; for to him
6150	Large compensation was from Elis due.
6150	Train'd to the course, four horses, with their cars,
6150	He for the Tripod at th' Elean games
6150	Had sent to run; these Augeas, King of men,
6150	Detain'd, and bade the drivers home return,
6150	Bootless, and grieving for their horses' loss.
6150	Th' old man his words resenting, and his acts,
6150	Large spoils retain'd; the rest among the crowd
6150	He shar'd, that none might lose his portion due.
6150	These we dispos'd of soon, and to the Gods
6150	Due off'rings made; but when the third day rose,
6150	Back in all haste, in numbers, horse and foot,
6150	Our foes return'd; with, them the Molion twins,
6150	Yet boys, untutor'd in the arts of war.
6150	Far off, by Alpheus' banks, th' extremest verge
6150	Of sandy Pylos, is a lofty mound,
6150	The city of Thryum; which around, intent
6150	To raze its walls, their army was encamp'd.
6150	The plain already they had overspread;
6150	When Pallas from Olympus' heights came down
6150	In haste, and bade us all prepare for war.
6150	On no unwilling ears her message fell,
6150	But eager all for fight; but me, to arm
6150	Neleus forbade, and e'en my horses hid,
6150	Deeming me yet unripe for deeds of war.
6150	Yet so, albeit on foot, by Pallas' grace
6150	A name I gain'd above our noblest horse.
6150	There is a river, Minyis by name,
6150	Hard by Arene, flowing to the sea,
6150	Where we, the Pylian horse, expecting morn,
6150	Encamp'd, by troops of footmen quickly join'd.
6150	Thence in all haste advancing, all in arms,
6150	We reach'd, by midday, Alpheus' sacred stream.
6150	There, to o'erruling Jove our off'rings made,
6150	To Alpheus and to Neptune each a bull,
6150	To Pallas, blue-ey'd Maid, a heifer fair,
6150	In order'd ranks we took our ev'ning meal,
6150	And each in arms upon the river's brink
6150	Lay down to rest; for close beside us lay
6150	Th' Epeians, on the town's destruction bent.
6150	Then saw they mighty deeds of war display'd;
6150	For we, as sunlight overspread the earth,
6150	To Jove and Pallas praying, battle gave.
6150	But when the Pylians and th' Epeians met,
6150	I first a warrior slew, and seiz'd his car,
6150	Bold spearman, Mulius; Augeas' son-in-law,
6150	His eldest daughter's husband, Agamede,
6150	The yellow-hair'd, who all the virtues knew
6150	Of each medicinal herb the wide world grows.
6150	Him, with my brass-tipp'd spear, as on he came,
6150	I slew; he fell; I, rushing to his car,
6150	Stood 'mid the foremost ranks; th' Epeians brave
6150	Fled diverse, when they saw their champion fall,
6150	Chief of their horsemen, foremost in the fight.
6150	With the dark whirlwind's force, I onward rush'd,
6150	And fifty cars I took; two men in each
6150	Fell to my spear, and bit the bloody dust.
6150	Then Actor's sons, the Molions, had I slain,
6150	Had not th' Earth-shaking God, their mighty sire,
6150	Veil'd in thick cloud, withdrawn them from the field;
6150	Then Jove great glory to the Pylians gave.
6150	For o'er the wide-spread plain we held pursuit,
6150	Slaying, and gath'ring up the scatter'd arms,
6150	Nor till corn-clad Buprasium, and the rock
6150	Olenian, and Alesium, term'd the Mound,
6150	Stay'd we our steeds; there Pallas bade us turn.
6150	There the last man I slew, and left; the Greeks
6150	Back from Buprasium drove their flying cars
6150	To Pylos, magnifying all the name,
6150	'Mid men, of Nestor, as 'mid Gods, of Jove.
6150	Such once was I 'mid men, while yet I was;
6150	Now to himself alone Achilles keeps
6150	His valour; yet hereafter, when the Greeks
6150	Have perish'd all, remorse shall touch his soul.
6150	Dear friend, remember now th' injunctions giv'n
6150	By old Menoetius, when from Phthian land
6150	He sent thee forth to Agamemnon's aid:
6150	I, and Laertes' godlike son, within,
6150	Heard all his counsel; to the well-built house
6150	Of Peleus we on embassy had come,
6150	Throughout Achaia's fertile lands to raise
6150	The means of war; Menoetius there we found,
6150	Achilles, and thyself within the house;
6150	While in the court-yard aged Peleus slew,
6150	And to the Lord of thunder offer'd up
6150	A fatten'd steer; and from a golden bowl
6150	O'er the burnt-off'ring pour'd the ruddy wine.
6150	We two, while ye were busied with the flesh,
6150	Stood at the gate; surpris'd, Achilles rose,
6150	And took us by the hand, and bade us sit,
6150	Dispensing all the hospitable rites.
6150	With food and wine recruited, I began
6150	My speech, and urg'd ye both to join the war:
6150	Nor were ye loth to go; much sage advice
6150	Your elders gave; old Peleus bade his son
6150	To aim at highest honours, and surpass
6150	His comrades all; Menoetius, Actor's son,
6150	To thee this counsel gave: 'My son,' he said,
6150	'Achilles is by birth above thee far;
6150	Thou art in years the elder; he in strength
6150	Surpasses thee; do thou with prudent words
6150	And timely speech address him, and advise
6150	And guide him; he will, to his good, obey.'
6150	""
6150	"""Such were the old man's words; but thou hast let"
6150	His counsel slip thy mem'ry; yet ev'n now
6150	Speak to Achilles thus, and stir his soul,
6150	If haply he will hear thee; and who knows
6150	But by the grace of Heav'n thou mayst prevail?
6150	For great is oft a friend's persuasive pow'r.
6150	But if the fear of evil prophesied,
6150	Or message by his Goddess-mother brought
6150	From Jove, restrain him, let him send thee forth
6150	With all his force of warlike Myrmidons,
6150	That thou mayst be the saving light of Greece.
6150	Then let him bid thee to the battle bear
6150	His glitt'ring arms; if so the men of Troy,
6150	Scar'd by his likeness, may forsake the field,
6150	And breathing-time afford the sons of Greece,
6150	Toil-worn; for little pause has yet been theirs.
6150	Fresh and unwearied, ye with ease may drive
6150	To their own city, from our ships and tents,
6150	"The Trojans, worn and battle-wearied men."""
6150	""
6150	Thus he; Patroclus' spirit within him burn'd,
6150	And tow'rd Achilles' tent in haste he sped.
6150	But, running, as Ulysses' ship he pass'd,
6150	Where was the Council and the Justice-seat,
6150	And where were built the altars of the Gods,
6150	There met him, halting from the battle-field,
6150	Shot through the thigh, Euaemon's Heav'n-born son,
6150	Eurypylus; his head and shoulders dank
6150	With clammy sweat, while from his grievous wound
6150	Stream'd the dark blood; yet firm was still his soul.
6150	Menoetius' noble son with pity saw,
6150	And deeply sorrowing thus address'd the chief:
6150	""
6150	"""Woe for the chiefs and councillors of Greece!"
6150	And must ye, far from friends and native home,
6150	Glut with your flesh the rav'ning dogs of Troy?
6150	Yet tell me this, Heav'n-born Eurypylus;
6150	Still do the Greeks 'gainst Hector's giant force
6150	"Make head? or fall they, vanquish'd by his spear?"""
6150	""
6150	To whom with prudent speech, Eurypylus:
6150	"""No source, Heav'n-born Patroclus, have the Greeks,"
6150	Of aid, but all must perish by their ships:
6150	For in the ships lie all our bravest late,
6150	By spear or arrow struck, by Trojan hands;
6150	And fiercer, hour by hour, their onset grows.
6150	But save me now, and lead me to the ships;
6150	There cut the arrow out, and from the wound
6150	With tepid water cleanse the clotted blood:
6150	Then soothing drugs apply, of healing pow'r,
6150	Which from Achilles, thou, 'tis said, hast learn'd,
6150	From Chiron, justest of the Centaurs, he.
6150	For Podalirius and Machaon both,
6150	Our leeches, one lies wounded in the tents,
6150	Himself requiring sore the leech's aid;
6150	"The other on the plain still dares the fight."""
6150	""
6150	To whom again Menoetius' noble son:
6150	"""How may this be? say, brave Eurypylus,"
6150	What must I do? a messenger am I,
6150	Sent by Gerenian Nestor, prop of Greece,
6150	With tidings to Achilles; yet ev'n so
6150	"I will not leave thee in this weary plight."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and passing his supporting hand
6150	Beneath his breast, the wounded warrior led
6150	Within the tent; th' attendant saw, and spread
6150	The ox-hide couch; then as he lay reclin'd,
6150	Patroclus, with his dagger, from the thigh
6150	Cut out the biting shaft; and from the wound
6150	With tepid water cleans'd the clotted blood;
6150	Then, pounded in his hands, a root applied
6150	Astringent, anodyne, which all his pain
6150	Allay'd; the wound was dried, and stanch'd the blood.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	ARGUMENT.
6150	""
6150	THE BATTLE AT THE GRECIAN WALL.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	The Greeks having retired into their entrenchments, Hector attempts to
6150	force them; but it proving impossible to pass the ditch, Polydamas
6150	advises to quit their chariots, and manage the attack on foot. The
6150	Trojans follow his counsel, and having divided their army into five
6150	bodies of foot, begin the assault. But upon the signal of an eagle with
6150	a serpent in his talons, which appeared on the left hand of the
6150	Trojans, Polydamas endeavours to withdraw them again. This Hector
6150	opposes, and continues the attack; in which, after many actions,
6150	Sarpedon makes the first breach in the wall: Hector also, casting a
6150	stone of a vast size, forces open one of the gates, and enters at the
6150	head of his troops, who victoriously pursue the Grecians even to their
6150	ships.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	BOOK XII.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	Thus o'er the wounded chief Eurypylus
6150	Watch'd in his tent Menoetius' noble son;
6150	But hand to hand the Greeks and Trojans fought;
6150	Nor longer might the ditch th' assault repel,
6150	Nor the broad wall above, which Greeks had built,
6150	To guard their ships, and round it dug the ditch;
6150	But to the Gods no hecatombs had paid,
6150	That they the ships and all the stores within
6150	Might safely keep; against the will of Heav'n
6150	The work was done, and thence not long endur'd.
6150	While Hector liv'd, and Peleus' son his wrath
6150	Retain'd, and Priam's city untaken stood;
6150	So long the Grecian wall remain'd entire:
6150	But of the Trojans when the best had fall'n,
6150	Of Greeks, when some were slain, some yet surviv'd;
6150	When the tenth year had seen the fall of Troy,
6150	And Greeks, embark'd, had ta'en their homeward way,
6150	Then Neptune and Apollo counsel took
6150	To sap the wall by aid of all the streams
6150	That seaward from the heights of Ida flow;
6150	Rhesus, Caresus, and Heptaporus,
6150	Granicus, and AEsepus, Rhodius,
6150	Scamander's stream divine, and Simois,
6150	Where helms and shields lay buried in the sand,
6150	And a whole race of warrior demigods:
6150	These all Apollo to one channel turn'd;
6150	Nine days against the wall the torrent beat;
6150	And Jove sent rain continuous, that the wall
6150	Might sooner be submerg'd; while Neptune's self,
6150	His trident in his hand, led on the stream,
6150	Washing away the deep foundations, laid,
6150	Laborious, by the Greeks, with logs and stones,
6150	Now by fast-flowing Hellespont dispers'd.
6150	The wall destroy'd, o'er all the shore he spread
6150	A sandy drift; and bade the streams return
6150	To where of old their silver waters flow'd.
6150	Such were, in future days, to be the works
6150	Of Neptune and Apollo; but meanwhile
6150	Fierce rag'd the battle round the firm-built wall,
6150	And frequent clatter'd on the turrets' beams
6150	The hostile missiles: by the scourge of Jove
6150	Subdued, the Greeks beside their ships were hemm'd,
6150	By Hector scar'd, fell minister of Dread,
6150	Who with the whirlwind's force, as ever, fought.
6150	As when, by dogs and hunters circled round,
6150	A boar, or lion, in his pride of strength,
6150	Turns on his foes, while they in close array
6150	Stand opposite, and frequent shoot their darts;
6150	Nor yet his spirit quails, but firm he stands
6150	With suicidal courage; swift he turns,
6150	Where best to break the circling ranks; where'er
6150	He makes his rush, the circling ranks give way:
6150	So Hector, here and there, amid the crowd,
6150	Urg'd his companions on to cross the ditch:
6150	The fiery steeds shrank back, and, snorting, stood
6150	Upon the topmost brink; for the wide ditch
6150	Withheld them, easy nor to leap nor cross:
6150	For steep arose on either side the banks,
6150	And at the top with sharpen'd stakes were crown'd,
6150	Thick-set and strong, which there the sons of Greece
6150	Had planted, to repel th' invading foes.
6150	Scarce might a horse, with well-wheel'd car attach'd,
6150	Essay the passage; but on foot they burn'd
6150	To make th' attempt; and thus Polydamas,
6150	Approaching near, to valiant Hector spoke:
6150	""
6150	"""Hector, and all ye other chiefs of Troy,"
6150	And brave Allies, in vain we seek to drive
6150	Our horses o'er the ditch; 'tis hard to cross;
6150	'Tis crown'd with pointed stakes, and them behind
6150	Is built the Grecian wall; there to descend
6150	And from our cars in narrow space to fight
6150	Were certain ruin. If it be indeed
6150	The will of Jove, high-thund'ring, to confound
6150	The Greeks in utter rout, and us to aid,
6150	I should rejoice that ev'ry Greek forthwith
6150	Far from his home should fill a nameless grave;
6150	But should they turn, and we again be driv'n
6150	Back from the ships, and hurried down the ditch,
6150	Such were our loss, that scarce a messenger
6150	Would live to bear the tidings to the town
6150	Of our destruction by the rallied Greeks.
6150	Hear then my counsel; let us all agree
6150	With our attendants here upon the bank
6150	To leave our horses; and ourselves on foot,
6150	All arm'd, press on where Hector leads; the Greeks,
6150	"If that their doom be nigh, will make no stand."""
6150	""
6150	Thus spoke Polydamas; his counsel pleas'd;
6150	And Hector sprang, in arms, from off his car;
6150	Nor long, the noble Hector when they saw,
6150	Delay'd the other chiefs; then gave command
6150	Each to his own attendant, by the ditch
6150	To keep the chariots all in due array;
6150	Then parting, form'd in order of attack,
6150	In five divisions, with their sev'ral chiefs.
6150	Round Hector throng'd, and bold Polydamas,
6150	The best and bravest; they who long'd the most
6150	To storm the wall, and fight beside the ships.
6150	With them Cebriones; for Hector left,
6150	To guard the horses, one of lesser note.
6150	The nest division was by Paris led,
6150	Agenor, and Alcathous; the third
6150	By Helenus, and brave Deiphobus,
6150	Two sons of Priam; Asius was the third,
6150	Asius, the son of Hyrtacus; who brought
6150	His tow'ring fiery steeds from Selles' stream,
6150	Hard by Arisba; stout AEneas led
6150	The fourth, Anchises' son, Archilochus
6150	With him, and Acamas, Antenor's sons;
6150	Both skill'd alike in ev'ry point of war.
6150	Of the far-fam'd Allies, Sarpedon held
6150	The chief command; and for his comrades chose
6150	Asteropaeus, and the warlike might
6150	Of Glaucus; these o'er all the rest he held
6150	Pre-eminent in valour, save himself,
6150	Who o'er them all superior stood confess'd.
6150	These, interlac'd their shields of tough bull's-hide,
6150	With eager step advanc'd, and deem'd the Greeks
6150	Would, unresisting, fall before their ships.
6150	The other Trojans and renown'd Allies
6150	The words of wise Polydamas obey'd:
6150	But Asius, son of Hyrtacus, refus'd
6150	His horses and his charioteer to leave,
6150	With them advancing to assail the ships.
6150	Blind fool, unconscious! from before those ships,
6150	Escap'd from death, with horses and with car
6150	Triumphant, to the breezy heights of Troy
6150	He never shall return; ill-omen'd fate
6150	O'ershadowing, dooms him by the spear to fall
6150	Of brave Idomeneus, Deucalion's son.
6150	He tow'rd the left inclin'd, what way the Greeks
6150	With horse and chariot from the plain return'd.
6150	That way he drove his horses; and the gates
6150	Unguarded found by bolt or massive bar.
6150	Their warders held them open'd wide, to save
6150	Perchance some comrade, flying from the plain.
6150	Thither he bent his course; with clamours loud
6150	Follow'd his troops; nor deem'd they that the Greeks
6150	Would hold their ground, but fall amid their ships.
6150	Little they knew; before the gates they found
6150	Two men, two warriors of the prime, two sons
6150	Illustrious of the spear-skill'd Lapithae:
6150	Stout Polypoetes one, Pirithous' son,
6150	With whom Leonteus, bold as blood-stain'd Mars:
6150	So stood these two before the lofty gates,
6150	As on the mountain side two tow'ring oaks,
6150	Which many a day have borne the wind and storm,
6150	Firm rifted by their strong continuous roots:
6150	So in their arms and vigour confident
6150	Those two great Asius' charge, undaunted, met.
6150	On th' other side, with, shouts and wild uproar,
6150	Their bull's-hide shields uplifted high, advanc'd
6150	Against the well-built wall, Asius the King,
6150	Iamenus, Orestes, Acamas
6150	The son of Asius, and OEnomaus,
6150	And Thoon; those within to save the ships
6150	Calling meanwhile on all the well-greav'd Greeks;
6150	But when they saw the wall by Trojans scal'd,
6150	And heard the cry of Greeks in panic fear,
6150	Sprang forth those two, before the gates to fight.
6150	As when two boars, upon the mountain side,
6150	Await th' approaching din of men and dogs,
6150	Then sideways rushing, snap the wood around,
6150	Ripp'd from the roots; loud clash their clatt'ring tusks,
6150	Till to the huntsman's spear they yield their lives;
6150	So clatter'd on those champions' brass-clad breasts
6150	The hostile weapons; stubbornly they fought,
6150	Relying on their strength, and friends above:
6150	For from the well-built tow'rs huge stones were hurl'd
6150	By those who for themselves, their tents and ships,
6150	Maintain'd defensive warfare; thick they fell,
6150	As wintry snow-flakes, which the boist'rous wind,
6150	Driving the shadowy clouds, spreads fast and close
6150	O'er all the surface of the fertile earth:
6150	So thick, from Grecian and from Trojan hands,
6150	The weapons flew; on helm and bossy shield
6150	With grating sound the pond'rous masses rang.
6150	Then deeply groaning, as he smote his thigh
6150	Thus spoke dismay'd the son of Hyrtacus:
6150	"""O Father Jove, how hast thou lov'd our hopes"
6150	To falsify, who deem'd not that the Greeks
6150	Would stand our onset, and resistless arms!
6150	But they, as yellow-banded wasps, or bees,
6150	That by some rocky pass have built their nests,
6150	Abandon not their cavern'd home, but wait
6150	Th' attack, and boldly for their offspring fight;
6150	So from the gates these two, though two alone,
6150	"Retire not, till they be or ta'en or slain."""
6150	""
6150	He said: but Jove regarded not his words;
6150	So much on Hector's triumph he was bent.
6150	Like battle rag'd round th' other gates; but hard
6150	It were for me, with godlike pow'r, to paint
6150	Each sev'ral combat; for around the wall
6150	A more than human storm of stone was pour'd
6150	On ev'ry side; the Greeks, hard press'd, perforce
6150	Fought for their ships, while all the Gods look'd on
6150	Indignant, who the Grecian cause upheld.
6150	Fiercely the Lapithae sustain'd the war:
6150	Stout Polypoetes first, Pirithous' son,
6150	Smote, through the brass-cheek'd helmet, Damasus;
6150	Nor stay'd the brazen helm the spear, whose point
6150	Went crashing through the bone, that all the brain
6150	Was shatter'd; onward as he rush'd, he fell.
6150	Then Pylon next, and Ormenus he slew:
6150	Meantime Leonteus, scion true of Mars,
6150	Struck with unerring spear Hippomachus,
6150	Son of Antimachus, below the waist;
6150	Then, drawing from the sheath his trenchant sword,
6150	Dash'd through the crowd, and hand to hand he smote
6150	Antiphates; he, backward, fell to earth.
6150	Menon, Iamenus, Orestes next,
6150	In quick succession to the ground he brought.
6150	From these while they their glitt'ring armour stripp'd,
6150	Round Hector throng'd, and bold Polydamas,
6150	The bravest and the best, who long'd the most
6150	To storm the wall, and burn with fire the ships.
6150	Yet on the margin of the ditch they paus'd;
6150	For, as they sought to cross, a sign from Heav'n
6150	Appear'd, to leftward of th' astonish'd crowd;
6150	A soaring eagle in his talons bore
6150	A dragon, huge of size, of blood-red hue,
6150	Alive, and breathing still, nor yet subdued;
6150	For twisting backward through the breast he pierc'd
6150	His bearer, near the neck; he, stung with pain,
6150	Let fall his prey, which dropp'd amid the crowd;
6150	Then screaming, on the blast was borne away.
6150	The Trojans, shudd'ring, in their midst beheld
6150	The spotted serpent, dire portent of Jove:
6150	Then to bold Hector thus Polydamas:
6150	"""Hector, in council thou reprov'st me oft"
6150	For good advice; it is not meet, thou say'st,
6150	That private men should talk beside the mark,
6150	In council or in war, but study still
6150	Thine honour to exalt; yet must I now
6150	Declare what seems to me the wisest course:
6150	Let us not fight the Greeks beside their ships;
6150	For thus I read the future, if indeed
6150	To us, about to cross, this sign from Heav'n
6150	Was sent, to leftward of th' astonish'd crowd:
6150	A soaring eagle, bearing in his claws
6150	A dragon, huge of size, of blood-red hue,
6150	Alive; yet dropp'd him ere he reach'd his home,
6150	Nor to his nestlings bore th' intended prey:
6150	So we, e'en though our mighty strength should break
6150	The gates and wall, and put the Greeks to rout,
6150	By the same road not scatheless should return,
6150	But many a Trojan on the field should leave,
6150	Slain by the Greeks, while they their ships defend.
6150	So would a seer, well vers'd in augury,
6150	"Worthy of public credit, read this sign."""
6150	""
6150	To whom thus Hector of the glancing helm
6150	"Replied, with stern regard: ""Polydamas,"
6150	This speech of thine is alien to my soul:
6150	Thy better judgment better counsel knows.
6150	But if in earnest such is thine advice,
6150	Thee of thy senses have the Gods bereft,
6150	Who fain wouldst have us disregard the word
6150	And promise by the nod of Jove confirm'd,
6150	And put our faith in birds' expanded wings;
6150	Little of these I reck, nor care to look,
6150	If to the right, and tow'rd the morning sun,
6150	Or to the left, and shades of night, they fly.
6150	Put we our trust in Jove's eternal will,
6150	Of mortals and Immortals King supreme.
6150	The best of omens is our country's cause.
6150	Why shouldst thou tremble at the battle strife?
6150	Though ev'ry Trojan else were doom'd to die
6150	Beside the ships, no fear lest thou shouldst fall:
6150	Unwarlike is thy soul, nor firm of mood:
6150	But if thou shrink, or by thy craven words
6150	Turn back another Trojan from the fight,
6150	"My spear shall take the forfeit of thy life."""
6150	""
6150	This said, he led the way; with joyous shouts
6150	They follow'd all; then Jove, the lightning's Lord,
6150	From Ida's heights a storm of wind sent down,
6150	Driving the dust against the Grecian ships;
6150	Which quell'd their courage, and to Hector gave,
6150	And to the Trojans, fresh incitement; they,
6150	On their own strength, and heav'nly signs relying,
6150	Their force address'd to storm the Grecian wall.
6150	They raz'd the counterscarp, the battlements
6150	Destroy'd; and the projecting buttresses,
6150	Which, to sustain the tow'rs, the Greeks had fix'd
6150	Deep in the soil, with levers undermin'd.
6150	These once withdrawn, they hop'd to storm the wall;
6150	Nor from the passage yet the Greeks withdrew,
6150	But closely fencing with their bull's-hide shields
6150	The broken battlements, they thence hurl'd down
6150	A storm of weapons on the foe beneath.
6150	Commanding from the tow'r in ev'ry place
6150	Were seen th' Ajaces, urging to the fight,
6150	Imploring these, and those in sterner tones
6150	Rebuking, who their warlike toil relax'd.
6150	""
6150	"""Friends, Grecians all, ye who excel in war,"
6150	And ye of mod'rate or inferior strength,
6150	Though all are not with equal pow'rs endued,
6150	Yet here is work for all! bear this in mind,
6150	Nor tow'rd the ships let any turn his face,
6150	By threats dismay'd; but forward press, and each
6150	Encourage each, if so the lightning's Lord,
6150	Olympian Jove, may grant us to repel,
6150	"And backward to his city chase the foe."""
6150	""
6150	Thus they, with cheering words, sustain'd the war:
6150	Thick as the snow-flakes on a wintry day,
6150	When Jove, the Lord of counsel, down on men
6150	His snow-storm sends, and manifests his pow'r:
6150	Hush'd are the winds; the flakes continuous fall,
6150	That the high mountain tops, and jutting crags,
6150	And lotus-cover'd meads are buried deep,
6150	And man's productive labours of the field;
6150	On hoary Ocean's beach and bays they lie,
6150	Th' approaching waves their bound; o'er all beside
6150	Is spread by Jove the heavy veil of snow.
6150	So thickly new the stones from either side,
6150	By Greeks on Trojans hurl'd, by these on Greeks;
6150	And clatter'd loud through all its length the wall.
6150	Nor yet the Trojans, though by Hector led,
6150	The gates had broken, and the massive bar,
6150	But Jove against the Greeks sent forth his son
6150	Sarpedon, as a lion on a herd:
6150	His shield's broad orb before his breast he bore,
6150	Well-wrought, of beaten brass, which th' arm'rer's hand
6150	Had beaten out, and lin'd with stout bull's-hide;
6150	With golden rods, continuous, all around;
6150	He thus equipp'd, two jav'lins brandishing,
6150	Strode onward, as a lion, mountain-bred,
6150	Whom, fasting long, his dauntless courage leads
6150	To assail the flock, though in well-guarded fold;
6150	And though the shepherds there he find, prepar'd
6150	With dogs and lances to protect the sheep,
6150	Not unattempted will he leave the fold;
6150	But, springing to the midst, he bears his prey
6150	In triumph thence; or in the onset falls,
6150	Wounded by jav'lins hurl'd by stalwart hands:
6150	So, prompted by his godlike courage, burn'd
6150	Sarpedon to assail the lofty wall,
6150	And storm the ramparts; and to Glaucus thus,
6150	Son of Hippolochus, his speech address'd:
6150	""
6150	"""Whence is it, Glaucus, that in Lycian land"
6150	We two at feasts the foremost seats may claim,
6150	The largest portions, and the fullest cups?
6150	Why held as Gods in honour? why endow'd
6150	With ample heritage, by Xanthus' banks,
6150	Of vineyard, and of wheat producing land?
6150	Then by the Lycians should we not be seen
6150	The foremost to affront the raging fight?
6150	So may our well-arm'd Lycians make their boast;
6150	'To no inglorious Kings we Lycians owe
6150	Allegiance; they on richest viands feed;
6150	Of luscious flavour drink the choicest wine;
6150	But still their valour brightest shows; and they,
6150	Where Lycians war, are foremost in the fight!'
6150	O friend! if we, survivors of this war,
6150	Could live, from age and death for ever free,
6150	Thou shouldst not see me foremost in the fight,
6150	Nor would I urge thee to the glorious field:
6150	But since on man ten thousand forms of death
6150	Attend, which none may 'scape, then on, that we
6150	"May glory on others gain, or they on us!"""
6150	""
6150	Thus he; nor Glaucus from his bidding shrank;
6150	And forward straight they led the Lycian pow'rs.
6150	Menestheus, son of Peteus, with dismay
6150	Observ'd their movement; for on his command,
6150	Inspiring terror, their attack was made.
6150	He look'd around him to the Grecian tow'rs,
6150	If any chief might there be found, to save
6150	His comrades from destruction; there he saw,
6150	Of war insatiable, th' Ajaces twain;
6150	And Teucer, from the tent but newly come,
6150	Hard by; nor yet could reach them with his voice;
6150	Such was the din, such tumult rose to Heav'n,
6150	From clatt'ring shields, and horsehair-crested helms,
6150	And batter'd gates, now all at once assail'd:
6150	Before them fiercely strove th' assaulting bands
6150	To break their way: he then Thootes sent,
6150	His herald, to th' Ajaces, craving aid.
6150	""
6150	"""Haste thee, Thootes, on th' Ajaces call,"
6150	Both, if it may be; so we best may hope
6150	To 'scape the death, which else is near at hand;
6150	So fierce the pressure of the Lycian chiefs,
6150	Undaunted now, as ever, in the fight.
6150	But if they too are hardly press'd, at least
6150	Let Ajax, son of Telamon, be spar'd,
6150	"And with him Teucer, skilled to draw the bow."""
6150	He said; the herald heard, and straight obey'd;
6150	Along the wall, where stood the brass-clad Greeks,
6150	He ran, and standing near th' Ajaces, said:
6150	""
6150	"""Ajaces, leaders of the brass-clad Greeks,"
6150	The son of Heav'n-born Peteus craves your aid.
6150	To share awhile the labours of his guard;
6150	Both, if it may be; so he best may hope
6150	To 'scape the death, which else is near at hand:
6150	So fierce the pressure of the Lycian chiefs,
6150	Undaunted now, as ever, in the fight.
6150	But if ye too are hardly press'd, at least
6150	Let Ajax, son of Telamon, be spar'd,
6150	"And with him Teucer, skill'd to draw the bow."""
6150	""
6150	He said: the mighty son of Telamon
6150	Consenting, thus addresss'd Oileus' son:
6150	"""Ajax, do thou and valiant Lyeomede"
6150	Exhort the Greeks the struggle to maintain;
6150	While I go yonder, to affront the war,
6150	"To aid their need, and back return in haste."""
6150	""
6150	Thus saying, Ajax Telamon set forth,
6150	And with him Teucer went, his father's son,
6150	While by Pandion Teucer's bow was borne.
6150	At brave Menestheus' tow'r, within the wall,
6150	Arriv'd, sore press'd they found the garrison;
6150	For like a whirlwind on the ramparts pour'd
6150	The Lycians' valiant councillors and chiefs.
6150	They quickly join'd the fray, and loud arose
6150	The battle-cry; first Ajax Telamon
6150	Sarpedon's comrade, brave Epicles, slew,
6150	Struck by a rugged stone, within the wall
6150	Which lay, the topmost of the parapet,
6150	Of size prodigious; which with both his hands
6150	A man in youth's full vigour scarce could raise,
6150	As men are now; he lifted it on high,
6150	And downward hurl'd; the four-peak'd helm it broke,
6150	Crushing the bone, and shatt'ring all the skull;
6150	He, like a diver, from the lofty tow'r
6150	Fell headlong down, and life forsook his bones,
6150	Teucer, meanwhile, from off the lofty wall
6150	The valiant Glaucus, pressing to the fight,
6150	Struck with an arrow, where he saw his arm
6150	Unguarded; he no longer brook'd the fray;
6150	Back from the wall he sprang, in hopes to hide
6150	From Grecian eyes his wound, that none might see,
6150	And triumph o'er him with insulting words.
6150	With grief Sarpedon saw his friend withdraw,
6150	Yet not relax'd his efforts; Thestor's son,
6150	Alcmaon, with his spear he stabb'd, and back
6150	The weapon drew; he, following, prostrate fell,
6150	And loudly rang his arms of polish'd brass.
6150	Then at the parapet, with stalwart hand,
6150	Sarpedon tugg'd; and yielding to his force
6150	Down fell the block entire; the wall laid bare,
6150	To many at once the breach gave open way.
6150	Ajax and Teucer him at once assail'd;
6150	This with an arrow struck the glitt'ring belt
6150	Around his breast, whence hung his pond'rous shield;
6150	But Jove, who will'd not that his son should fall
6150	Before the ships, the weapon turn'd aside.
6150	Then forward Ajax sprang, and with his spear
6150	Thrust at the shield; the weapon pass'd not through,
6150	Yet check'd his bold advance; a little space
6150	Back he recoil'd, but not the more withdrew,
6150	His soul on glory intent; and rallying quick,
6150	Thus to the warlike Lycians shouted loud:
6150	""
6150	"""Why, Lycians, thus your wonted might relax?"
6150	'Tis hard for one alone, how brave soe'er,
6150	E'en though he break the rampart down, to force
6150	A passage to the ships; but on with me!
6150	"For work is here for many hands to do."""
6150	""
6150	He said; and by the King's rebuke abash'd,
6150	With fiercer zeal the Lycians press'd around
6150	Their King and councillor; on th' other side
6150	Within the wall the Greeks their squadrons mass'd;
6150	Then were great deeds achiev'd; nor thro' the breach
6150	Could the brave troops of Lycia to the ships
6150	Their passage force; nor could the warrior Greeks
6150	Repel the Lycians from the ground, where they,
6150	Before the wall, had made their footing good.
6150	As when two neighbours, in a common field,
6150	Each line in hand, within a narrow space,
6150	About the limits of their land contend;
6150	Between them thus the rampart drew the line;
6150	O'er which the full-orb'd shields of tough bull's-hide,
6150	And lighter bucklers on the warriors' breasts
6150	On either side they clove; and many a wound
6150	The pitiless weapons dealt, on some who, turn'd,
6150	Their neck and back laid bare; on many more,
6150	Who full in front, and through their shields were struck.
6150	On ev'ry side the parapet and tow'rs
6150	With Greek and Trojan blood were spatter'd o'er.
6150	Nor yet, e'en so, the Greeks to flight were driv'n;
6150	But as a woman that for wages spins,
6150	Honest and true, with wool and weights in hand,
6150	In even balance holds the scales, to mete
6150	Her humble hire, her children's maintenance;
6150	So even hung the balance of the war,
6150	Till Jove with highest honour Hector crown'd,
6150	The son of Priam; he, the foremost, scal'd
6150	The wall, and loudly on the Trojans call'd:
6150	""
6150	"""On, valiant Trojans, on! the Grecian wall"
6150	"Break down, and wrap their ships in blazing fires."""
6150	""
6150	Thus he, exhorting, spoke; they heard him all,
6150	And to the wall rush'd numberless, and swarm'd
6150	Upon the ramparts, bristling thick with spears.
6150	Then Hector, stooping, seiz'd a pond'rous stone
6150	That lay before the gates; 'twas broad below,
6150	But sharp above; and scarce two lab'ring men,
6150	The strongest, from the ground could raise it up,
6150	And load upon a wain; as men are now;
6150	But he unaided lifted it with ease,
6150	So light it seem'd, by grace of Saturn's son.
6150	As in one hand a shepherd bears with ease
6150	A full-siz'd fleece, and scarcely feels the weight;
6150	So Hector tow'rd the portals bore the stone,
6150	Which clos'd the lofty double-folding gates,
6150	Within defended by two massive bars
6150	Laid crosswise, and with one cross bolt secur'd.
6150	Close to the gate he stood; and planting firm
6150	His foot, to give his arm its utmost pow'r,
6150	Full on the middle dash'd the mighty mass.
6150	The hinges both gave way; the pond'rous stone
6150	Fell inwards; widely gap'd the op'ning gates;
6150	Nor might the bars within the blow sustain:
6150	This way and that the sever'd portals flew
6150	Before the crashing missile; dark as night
6150	His low'ring brow, great Hector sprang within;
6150	Bright flash'd the brazen armour on his breast,
6150	As through the gates, two jav'lins in his hand,
6150	He sprang; the Gods except, no pow'r might meet
6150	That onset; blaz'd his eyes with lurid fire.
6150	Then to the Trojans, turning to the throng,
6150	He call'd aloud to scale the lofty wall;
6150	They heard, and straight obey'd; some scal'd the wall:
6150	Some through the strong-built gates continuous pour'd;
6150	While in confusion irretrievable
6150	Fled to their ships the panic-stricken Greeks.
6150	""
6150	END OF VOLUME I.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	VOLUME II.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	ARGUMENT.
6150	""
6150	THE FOURTH BATTLE CONTINUED, IN WHICH NEPTUNE ASSISTS THE GREEKS. THE
6150	ACTS OF IDOMENEUS.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	Neptune, concerned for the loss of the Grecians, upon seeing the
6150	fortification forced by Hector (who had entered the gate near the
6150	station of the Ajaces), assumes the shape of Calchas, and inspires
6150	those heroes to oppose him; then, in the form of one of the generals,
6150	encourages the other Greeks who had retired to their vessels. The
6150	Ajaces form their troops into a close phalanx, and put a stop to Hector
6150	and the Trojans. Several deeds of valour are performed; Meriones,
6150	losing his spear in the encounter, repairs to seek another at the tent
6150	of Idomeneus; this occasions a conversation between these two warriors,
6150	who return together to the battle. Idomeneus signalizes his courage
6150	above the rest; he kills Othryoneus, Asius, and Alcathous; Deiphobus
6150	and AEneas march against him, and at length Idomeneus retires. Menelaus
6150	wounds Helenus and kills Peisander. The Trojans are repulsed in the
6150	left wing. Hector still keeps his ground against the Ajaces, till,
6150	being galled by the Locrian slingers and archers, Polydamas advises to
6150	call a council of war: Hector approves his advice, but goes first to
6150	rally the Trojans; upbraids Paris, rejoins Polydamas, meets Ajax again,
6150	and renews the attack.
6150	""
6150	The eight-and-twentieth day still continues. The scene is between the
6150	Grecian wall and the sea-shore.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	BOOK XIII.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	When Jove had Hector and the Trojans brought
6150	Close to the ships, he left them there to toil
6150	And strife continuous; turning his keen glance
6150	To view far off th' equestrian tribes of Thrace,
6150	The warlike Mysians, and the men who feed
6150	On milk of mares, thence Hippemolgi term'd;
6150	A peaceful race, the justest of mankind.
6150	On Troy he turn'd not once his piercing glance;
6150	Nor deem'd he any God would dare to give
6150	To Trojans or to Greeks his active aid.
6150	""
6150	No careless watch the monarch Neptune kept:
6150	Wond'ring, he view'd the battle, where he sat
6150	Aloft on wooded Samos' topmost peak,
6150	Samos of Thrace; whence Ida's heights he saw,
6150	And Priam's city, and the ships of Greece.
6150	""
6150	Thither ascended from the sea, he sat;
6150	And thence the Greeks, by Trojans overborne,
6150	Pitying he saw, and deeply wroth with Jove.
6150	Then down the mountain's craggy side he pass'd
6150	With rapid step; and as he mov'd along,
6150	Beneath th' immortal feet of Ocean's Lord
6150	Quak'd the huge mountain and the shadowy wood.
6150	Three strides he took; the fourth, he reach'd his goal,
6150	AEgae; where on the margin of the bay
6150	His temple stood, all glitt'ring, all of gold,
6150	Imperishable; there arriv'd, he yok'd
6150	Beneath his car the brazen-footed steeds,
6150	Of swiftest flight, with manes of flowing gold.
6150	All clad in gold, the golden lash he grasp'd
6150	Of curious work, and mounting on his car,
6150	Skimm'd o'er the waves; from all the depths below
6150	Gamboll'd around the monsters of the deep,
6150	Acknowledging their King; the joyous sea
6150	Parted her waves; swift flew the bounding steeds,
6150	Nor was the brazen axle wet with spray,
6150	When to the ships of Greece their Lord they bore.
6150	""
6150	Down in the deep recesses of the sea
6150	A spacious cave there is, which lies midway
6150	'Twixt Tenedos and Imbros' rocky isle:
6150	Th' Earth-shaking Neptune there his coursers stay'd,
6150	Loos'd from the chariot, and before them plac'd
6150	Ambrosial provender; and round their feet
6150	Shackles of gold, which none might break nor loose,
6150	That there they might await their Lord's return;
6150	Then to the Grecian army took his way.
6150	""
6150	Meantime, by Hector, son of Priam, led,
6150	Like fire, or whirlwind, press'd the Trojans on,
6150	With furious zeal, and shouts and clamour hoarse;
6150	In hopes to take the ships, and all the chiefs
6150	To slay beside them; but from Ocean's depths
6150	Uprose th' Earth-shaker, Circler of the Earth,
6150	To Calchas' likeness and deep voice conform'd,
6150	And rous'd the fainting Greeks; th' Ajaces first,
6150	Themselves with ardour fill'd, he thus address'd:
6150	"""'Tis yours, Ajaces, fill'd with courage high,"
6150	Discarding chilly fear, to save the Greeks:
6150	Elsewhere I dread not much the Trojan force,
6150	Though they in crowds have scal'd the lofty wall;
6150	The well-greav'd Greeks their onset may defy.
6150	Yet greatly fear I lest we suffer loss,
6150	Where that fierce, fiery madman, Hector, leads.
6150	Who boasts himself the son of Jove most high.
6150	But may some God your hearts inspire, yourselves
6150	Firmly to stand, and cheer your comrades on;
6150	So from your swiftly-sailing ships ye yet
6150	May drive the foe, how bold soe'er he be,
6150	"Though by Olympian Jove himself upheld."""
6150	""
6150	So spake th' Earth-shaker, Circler of the Earth,
6150	And with his sceptre touching both the chiefs,
6150	Fill'd them with strength and courage, and their limbs,
6150	Their feet and hands, with active vigour strung;
6150	Then like a swift-wing'd falcon sprang to flight,
6150	Which down the sheer face of some lofty rock
6150	Swoops on the plain to seize his feather'd prey:
6150	So swiftly Neptune left the chiefs; him first
6150	Departing, knew Oileus' active son,
6150	And thus the son of Telamon address'd:
6150	"""Ajax, since some one of th' Olympian Gods,"
6150	In likeness of a seer, hath hither come
6150	To urge us to the war (no Calchas he,
6150	Our augur Heav'n-inspir'd; for well I mark'd
6150	His movements, as he went; and of a God
6150	'Tis easy to discern the outward signs),
6150	I feel fresh spirit kindled in my breast,
6150	"And new-born vigour in my feet and hands."""
6150	""
6150	Whom answer'd thus the son of Telamon:
6150	"""My hands too grasp with firmer hold the spear,"
6150	My spirit like thine is stirr'd; I feel my feet
6150	Instinct with fiery life; nor should I fear
6150	With Hector, son of Priam, in his might
6150	"Alone to meet, and grapple to the death."""
6150	""
6150	Such was their mutual converse, as they joy'd
6150	In the fierce transport by the God inspir'd.
6150	Neptune, meanwhile, the other Greeks arous'd,
6150	Who, to the ships withdrawn, their wasted strength
6150	Recruited; for their limbs were faint with toil,
6150	And grief was in their hearts, as they beheld
6150	The Trojan hosts that scal'd the lofty wall;
6150	They saw, and from their eyes the teardrops fell,
6150	Of safety desp'rate; but th' Earth-shaking God
6150	Amid their ranks appearing, soon restor'd
6150	Their firm array; to Teucer first he came,
6150	To Leitus, and valiant Peneleus,
6150	Thoas, Deipyrus, Meriones,
6150	And young Antilochus, brave warriors all,
6150	And to the chiefs his winged words address'd:
6150	""
6150	"""Shame on ye, Grecian youths! to you I look'd"
6150	As to our ships' defenders; but if ye
6150	Shrink from the perilous battle, then indeed
6150	Our day is come, to be by Troy subdu'd.
6150	O Heav'n! a sad and wondrous sight is this,
6150	A sight I never deem'd my eyes should see,
6150	Our ships assail'd by Trojan troops; by those
6150	Who heretofore have been as tim'rous hinds
6150	Amid the forest depths, the helpless prey
6150	Of jackals, pards, and wolves; they here and there,
6150	Uncertain, heartless, unresisting, fly:
6150	Such were the Trojans once; nor dar'd abide,
6150	No, not an hour, the strength and arms of Greece;
6150	And these are they, who now beside our ships,
6150	Far from their city walls, maintain the fight,
6150	Embolden'd by our great commander's fault,
6150	And slackness of the people, who, with him
6150	Offended, scarce are brought to guard our ships.
6150	And, feebly fighting, are beside them slain.
6150	E'en though the mighty monarch, Atreus' son,
6150	Wide-ruling Agamemnon, be in truth
6150	Wholly to blame in this, that he hath wrong'd
6150	The son of Peleus, yet 'tis not for us
6150	Our courage to relax. Arouse ye then!
6150	A brave man's spirit its vigour soon regains.
6150	That ye, the best and bravest of the host,
6150	Should stand aloof thus idly, 'tis not well;
6150	If meaner men should from the battle shrink,
6150	I might not blame them; but that such as ye
6150	Should falter, indignation fills my soul.
6150	Dear friends, from this remissness must accrue
6150	Yet greater evils; but with gen'rous shame
6150	And keen remorse let each man's breast be fill'd;
6150	Fierce is the struggle; in his pride of strength
6150	Hector has forc'd the gates and massive bars,
6150	"And raging, 'mid the ships maintains the war."""
6150	""
6150	Thus Neptune on the Greeks, reproving, call'd:
6150	Then round th' Ajaces twain were cluster'd thick
6150	The serried files, whose firm array nor Mars,
6150	Nor spirit-stirring Pallas might reprove:
6150	For there, the bravest all, in order due,
6150	Waited the Trojan charge by Hector led:
6150	Spear close by spear, and shield by shield o'erlaid,
6150	Buckler to buckler press'd, and helm to helm,
6150	And man to man; the horsehair plumes above,
6150	That nodded on the warriors' glitt'ring crests,
6150	Each other touch'd; so closely massed they stood.
6150	Backward, by many a stalwart hand, were drawn
6150	The spears, in act to hurl; their eyes and minds
6150	Turn'd to the front, and eager for the fray.
6150	On pour'd the Trojan masses; in the van
6150	Hector straight forward urg'd his furious course.
6150	As some huge boulder, from its rocky bed
6150	Detach'd, and by the wintry torrent's force
6150	Hurl'd down the cliff's steep face, when constant rains
6150	The massive rock's firm hold have undermin'd;
6150	With giant bounds it flies; the crashing wood
6150	Resounds beneath it; still it hurries on,
6150	Until, arriving at the level plain,
6150	Its headlong impulse check'd, it rolls no more;
6150	So Hector, threat'ning now through ships and tents,
6150	E'en to the sea, to force his murd'rous way,
6150	Anon, confronted by that phalanx firm,
6150	Halts close before it; while the sons of Greece,
6150	With thrust of sword and double-pointed spears,
6150	Stave off his onset; he a little space
6150	Withdrew, and loudly on the Trojans call'd:
6150	""
6150	"""Trojans, and Lycians, and ye Dardans fam'd"
6150	In close encounter, stand ye firm! not long
6150	The Greeks, though densely mass'd, shall bar my way,
6150	But soon, methinks, before my spear shall quail,
6150	If from the chief of Gods my mission be,
6150	"From Jove the Thund'rer, royal Juno's Lord."""
6150	""
6150	His words fresh courage rais'd in ev'ry breast;
6150	On loftiest deeds intent, Deiphobus,
6150	The son of Priam, from the foremost ranks,
6150	His shield's broad orb before him borne, advanc'd
6150	With airy step, protected by the shield:
6150	At him Meriones with glitt'ring spear
6150	Took aim, nor miss'd his mark; the shield's broad orb
6150	Of tough bull's-hide it struck; but pass'd not through,
6150	For near the head the sturdy shaft was snapp'd.
6150	Yet from before his breast Deiphobus
6150	Held at arm's length his shield; for much he fear'd
6150	The weapon of Meriones; but he
6150	Back to his comrades' shelt'ring ranks withdrew,
6150	Griev'd at his baffled hopes and broken spear.
6150	Then tow'rd the ships he bent his steps, to seek
6150	Another spear, which in his tent remain'd.
6150	The rest, 'mid wild uproar, maintain'd the fight.
6150	""
6150	There Teucer first, the son of Telamon,
6150	A warrior slew, the son of Mentor, Lord
6150	Of num'rous horses, Imbrius, spearman skill'd.
6150	In former days, ere came the sons of Greece,
6150	He in Pedaeus dwelt, and had to wife
6150	Medesicaste, Priam's bastard child;
6150	But when the well-trimm'd ships of Greece appear'd,
6150	Return'd to Troy; and there, rever'd by all,
6150	With Priam dwelt, who lov'd him as a son.
6150	Him Teucer with his lance below the ear
6150	Stabb'd, and drew back the weapon; down he fell,
6150	As by the woodman's axe, on some high peak,
6150	Falls a proud ash, conspicuous from afar,
6150	Scatt'ring its tender foliage on the ground;
6150	He fell; and loud his burnish'd armour rang.
6150	Forth Teucer sprang to seize the spoil; at whom,
6150	Advancing, Hector aim'd his glitt'ring spear;
6150	He saw, and, stooping, shunn'd the brazen death
6150	A little space; but through the breast it struck
6150	Amphimachus, the son of Cteatus,
6150	The son of Actor, hastening to the fight:
6150	Thund'ring he fell, and loud his armour rang.
6150	Then forward Hector sprang, in hopes to seize
6150	The brazen helm, that fitted well the brow
6150	Of brave Amphimachus; but Ajax met
6150	Th' advance of Hector with his glitt'ring spear;
6150	Himself he reach'd not, all in dazzling brass
6150	Encas'd; but pressing on his bossy shield
6150	Drove by main force beyond where lay the dead:
6150	Them both the Greeks withdrew; th' Athenian chiefs
6150	Stychius and brave Menestheus, bore away
6150	Amid the ranks of Greece Amphimachus;
6150	While, as two lions high above the ground
6150	Bear through the brushwood in their jaws a goat,
6150	Snatch'd from the sharp-fang'd dogs' protecting care:
6150	So, fill'd with warlike rage, th' Ajaces twain
6150	Lifted on high, and of its armour stripp'd
6150	The corpse of Imbrius; and Oileus' son,
6150	Griev'd at Amphimachus, his comrade's death,
6150	Cut from the tender neck, and like a ball
6150	Sent whirling through the crowd the sever'd head;
6150	And in the dust at Hector's feet it fell.
6150	Then, for his grandson slain, fierce anger fill'd
6150	The breast of Neptune; through the tents of Greece
6150	And ships he pass'd, the Greeks encouraging,
6150	And ills preparing for the sons of Troy.
6150	Him met Idomeneus, the warrior King,
6150	Leaving a comrade, from the battle field,
6150	Wounded behind the knee, but newly brought;
6150	Borne by his comrades, to the leech's care
6150	He left him, eager to rejoin the fray;
6150	Whom by his tent th' Earth-shaking God address'd,
6150	The voice assuming of Andraemon's son,
6150	Who o'er th' AEtolians, as a God rever'd,
6150	In Pleuron reign'd, and lofty Calydon:
6150	""
6150	"""Where now, Idomeneus, sage Cretan chief,"
6150	Are all the vaunting threats, so freely pour'd
6150	"Against the Trojans by the sons of Greece?"""
6150	""
6150	To whom the Cretan King, Idomeneus:
6150	"""Thoas, on none, so far as I may judge,"
6150	May blame be cast; we all our duties know;
6150	Nor see I one by heartless fear restrain'd,
