6150	Nor hanging back, and flinching from the war:
6150	Yet by th' o'erruling will of Saturn's son
6150	It seems decreed that here the Greeks should fall,
6150	And far from Argos lie in nameless graves.
6150	But, Thoas, as thyself art ever staunch,
6150	Nor slow the laggards to reprove, thy work
6150	"Remit not now; but rouse each sev'ral man."""
6150	""
6150	To whom Earth-shaking Neptune thus replied:
6150	"""Idomeneus, may he from Troy return"
6150	No more, but here remain to glut the dogs,
6150	If such there be, from this day's fight who shrinks.
6150	But haste thee, don thine arms; great need is now
6150	To hasten, if in aught we two may serve:
6150	E'en meaner men, united, courage gain;
6150	"But we the bravest need not fear to meet."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and to the strife of men return'd.
6150	Within his well-constructed tent arriv'd,
6150	Straight donn'd Idomeneus his armour bright:
6150	Two spears he took; and, like the lightning's flash,
6150	Which, as a sign to men, the hand of Jove
6150	Hurls downwards from Olympus' glitt'ring heights;
6150	Whose dazzling radiance far around is thrown;
6150	Flash'd, as the warrior ran, his armour bright.
6150	Him met Meriones, his follower brave,
6150	Close to the tent; to seek a spear he came;
6150	"To whom Idomeneus: ""Meriones,"
6150	Swift-footed son of Molus, comrade dear,
6150	Why com'st thou here, and leav'st the battle field?
6150	Hast thou some wound receiv'd, whereof the pain
6150	Subdues thy spirit? or com'st thou, to the field
6150	To summon me? unsummon'd, well thou know'st
6150	"I better love the battle than the tent."""
6150	""
6150	Whom answer'd thus the sage Meriones:
6150	"""Idomeneus, the brass-clad Cretans' King,"
6150	I come to seek a spear, if haply such
6150	Within thy tent be found; for, in the fight,
6150	That which I lately bore, e'en now I broke
6150	"Against the shield of brave Deiphobus."""
6150	""
6150	To whom Idomeneus, the Cretan King:
6150	"""Of spears, or one, or twenty, if thou list,"
6150	Thou there mayst find against the polish'd wall.
6150	The spoil of Trojans slain; for with my foes
6150	'Tis not my wont to wage a distant war.
6150	Thence have I store of spears, and bossy shields,
6150	"And crested helms, and breastplates polish'd bright."""
6150	""
6150	Whom answer'd thus the sage Meriones:
6150	"""Nor are my tent and dark-ribb'd ship devoid"
6150	Of Trojan spoils; but they are far to seek;
6150	Nor deem I that my hand is slack in fight;
6150	For 'mid the foremost in the glorious strife
6150	I stand, whene'er is heard the battle cry.
6150	My deeds by others of the brass-clad Greeks
6150	"May not be noted; but thou know'st them well."""
6150	""
6150	To whom Idomeneus, the Cretan King:
6150	"""What need of this? thy prowess well I know;"
6150	For should we choose our bravest through the fleet
6150	To man the secret ambush, surest test
6150	Of warriors' courage, where is manifest
6150	The diff'rence 'twixt the coward and the brave;
6150	(The coward's colour changes, nor his soul
6150	Within his breast its even balance keeps,
6150	But changing still, from foot to foot he shifts,
6150	And in his bosom loudly beats his heart,
6150	Expecting death; and chatter all his teeth:
6150	The brave man's colour changes not; no fear
6150	He knows, the ambush ent'ring; all his pray'r
6150	Is that the hour of battle soon may come)
6150	E'en there, thy courage none might call in doubt.
6150	Shouldst thou from spear or sword receive a wound,
6150	Not on thy neck behind, nor on thy back
6150	Would fall the blow, but on thy breast, in front,
6150	Still pressing onward 'mid the foremost ranks.
6150	But come, prolong we not this idle talk,
6150	Like babblers vain, who scorn might justly move:
6150	"Haste to my tent, and there select thy spear."""
6150	""
6150	He said: and from the tent Meriones,
6150	Valiant as Mars, his spear selected straight,
6150	And, eager for the fray, rejoin'd his chief.
6150	As Mars, the bane of men, goes forth to war,
6150	Attended by his strong, unfearing son,
6150	Terror, who shakes the bravest warrior's soul;
6150	They two, from Thrace, against the Ephyri,
6150	Or haughty Phlegyans arm; nor hear alike
6150	The pray'rs of both the combatants, one side
6150	With vict'ry crowning; so to battle went
6150	Those leaders twain, in dazzling arms array'd:
6150	Then thus Meriones his chief address'd:
6150	""
6150	"""Son of Deucalion, say if on the right,"
6150	Or on the centre of the gen'ral host,
6150	Our onset should be made, or on the left;
6150	"For there, methinks, most succour need the Greeks."""
6150	""
6150	To whom Idomeneus, the Cretan chief:
6150	"""Others there are the centre to defend,"
6150	Th' Ajaces both, and Teucer, of the Greeks
6150	Best archer, good too in the standing fight;
6150	These may for Hector full employment find,
6150	Brave as he is, and eager for the fray;
6150	E'en for his courage 'twere a task too hard,
6150	Their might to conquer, and resistless hands,
6150	And burn the ships, if Saturn's son himself
6150	Fire not, and 'mid the shipping throw the torch.
6150	Great Ajax Telamon to none would yield,
6150	Of mortal birth, by earthly food sustain'd,
6150	By spear or pond'rous stone assailable;
6150	In hand to hand encounter, scarce surpass'd
6150	By Peleus' son Achilles; though with him
6150	In speed of foot he might not hope to vie.
6150	Then on the left let us our onset make;
6150	And quickly learn if we on others' heads
6150	"Are doom'd to win renown, or they on ours."""
6150	""
6150	He said: and, brave as Mars, Meriones,
6150	Thither where he directed, led the way.
6150	Now when, attended thus, Idomeneus,
6150	Like blazing fire, in dazzling arms appear'd,
6150	Around him throng'd, with rallying cries, the Greeks,
6150	And rag'd beside the ships the balanc'd fight.
6150	As, when the dust lies deepest on the roads,
6150	Before the boist'rous winds the storm drives fast,
6150	And high at once the whirling clouds are toss'd;
6150	So was the fight confus'd; and in the throng
6150	Each man with keen desire of slaughter burn'd.
6150	Bristled the deadly strife with pond'rous spears,
6150	Wielded with dire intent; the brazen gleam
6150	Dazzled the sight, by flashing helmets cast,
6150	And breastplates polish'd bright, and glitt'ring shields
6150	Commingling; stern of heart indeed were he,
6150	Who on that sight with joy, not pain, could gaze.
6150	""
6150	Dire evil then on mortal warriors brought
6150	The diverse minds of Saturn's mighty sons:
6150	To Hector and the Trojans Jove design'd,
6150	In honour of Achilles, swift of foot,
6150	To give the vict'ry; yet not utterly
6150	He will'd to slay before the walls of Troy
6150	The Grecian host; but glory to confer
6150	On Thetis and her noble-minded son.
6150	Neptune, on th' other side, the Greeks inspir'd,
6150	Clandestine rising from the hoary sea;
6150	For them before the Trojan host o'erborne
6150	He saw with grief, and deeply wroth with Jove.
6150	Equal the rank of both, their birth the same,
6150	But Jove in wisdom, as in years, the first.
6150	Nor ventur'd Neptune openly to aid
6150	The cause of Greece; but cloth'd in mortal form,
6150	In secret still the army's courage rous'd.
6150	This way and that they tugg'd of furious war
6150	And balanc'd strife, where many a warrior fell,
6150	The straining rope, which none might break or loose.
6150	Then, though his hair was grizzl'd o'er with age,
6150	Calling the Greeks to aid, Idomeneus,
6150	Inspiring terror, on the Trojans sprang,
6150	And slew Othryoneus, who but of late
6150	Came from Cabesus on the alarm of war;
6150	And, welcomed as a guest in Priam's house,
6150	The fairest of his daughters sought to wed,
6150	No portion asked, Cassandra; mighty deeds
6150	He promis'd, from before the walls of Troy
6150	In their despite to drive the sons of Greece.
6150	The aged Priam listen'd to his snit;
6150	And he, his promise trusting, fought for Troy.
6150	Him, marching with proud step, Idomeneus
6150	Struck with his glitt'ring spear, nor aught avail'd
6150	His brazen breastplate; through the middle thrust,
6150	Thund'ring he fell: the victor vaunting cried:
6150	""
6150	"""Othryoneus, above all mortal men"
6150	I hold thee in respect, if thou indeed
6150	Wilt make thy words to aged Priam good,
6150	Who promis'd thee his daughter in return:
6150	We too would offer thee a like reward;
6150	And give thee here to wed, from Argos brought,
6150	Atrides' fairest daughter, if with us
6150	Thou wilt o'erthrow the well-built walls of Troy.
6150	Come then, on board our ocean-going ships
6150	Discuss the marriage contract; nor shall we
6150	"Be found illib'ral of our bridal gifts."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and seizing by the foot the slain,
6150	Dragg'd from the press; but to the rescue came
6150	Asius, himself on foot before his car:
6150	So close his charioteer the horses held,
6150	They breath'd upon his shoulders; eagerly
6150	He sought to reach Idomeneus; but he,
6150	Preventing, through his gullet drove the spear,
6150	Beneath his chin; right through the weapon pass'd;
6150	He fell; as falls an oak, or poplar tall,
6150	Or lofty pine, which on the mountain top,
6150	For some proud ship, the woodman's axe hath hewn:
6150	So he, before the car and horses stretch'd,
6150	His death-cry utt'ring, clutch'd the blood-stain'd soil;
6150	Bewilder'd, helpless, stood his charioteer;
6150	Nor dar'd, escaping from the foemen's hands,
6150	To turn his horses: him, Antilochus
6150	Beneath the waistband struck; nor aught avail'd
6150	His brazen breastplate; through the middle thrust,
6150	He, from the well-wrought chariot, gasping, fell.
6150	Antilochus, the noble Nestor's son,
6150	The horses seiz'd, and from the Trojan ranks
6150	Drove to the Grecian camp. For Asius' death
6150	Deep griev'd, Deiphobus, approaching, hurl'd
6150	Against Idomeneus his glitt'ring spear:
6150	The coming weapon he beheld, and shunn'd:
6150	Beneath the ample circle of his shield,
6150	With hides and brazen plates encircled round,
6150	And by two rods sustain'd, conceal'd he stood:
6150	Beneath he crouch'd, and o'er him flew the spear:
6150	Yet harsh it grated, glancing from the shield;
6150	Nor bootless from that stalwart hand it flew,
6150	But through the midriff, close below the heart,
6150	Hypsenor, son of Hippasus, it struck,
6150	And straight relax'd his limbs; then shouting loud,
6150	In boastful tone, Deiphobus exclaim'd:
6150	""
6150	"""Not unaveng'd lies Asius; he, methinks,"
6150	As I have found him fellowship, with joy
6150	"Thro' Hades' strongly-guarded gates may pass."""
6150	He said; the Greeks, indignant, heard his boast;
6150	Chief, of Antilochus the manly soul
6150	Was stirr'd within him; yet amid his grief
6150	His comrade not forgetting, up he ran,
6150	And o'er him spread the cover of his shield.
6150	Meanwhile, two trusty friends, Mecistheus, son
6150	Of Echius, and Alastor, rais'd the slain,
6150	And deeply groaning bore him to the ships.
6150	Nor did Idomeneus his noble rage
6150	Abate; still burning o'er some Trojan soul
6150	To draw the gloomy veil of night and death;
6150	Or, having sav'd the Greeks, himself to fall.
6150	Then high-born AEsuetes' son he slew,
6150	Alcathous; he, Anchises' son-in-law,
6150	The eldest of his daughters had to wife,
6150	Hippodamia; by her parents both,
6150	O'er all, belov'd; in beauty, skill, and mind,
6150	All her compeers surpassing; wife of one,
6150	The noblest man through all the breadth of Troy.
6150	Him Neptune by Idomeneus subdued;
6150	Seal'd his quick eyes, his active limbs restrain'd,
6150	Without the pow'r to fly, or shun the spear;
6150	Fix'd as a pillar, or a lofty tree,
6150	He stood, while through his breast Idomeneus
6150	His weapon drove; the brazen mail it broke,
6150	Which oft had turn'd aside the stroke of death;
6150	Harshly it grated, sever'd by the spear:
6150	He fell; the spear-point quiv'ring in his heart,
6150	Which with convulsive throbbings shook the shaft.
6150	There Mars its course arrested. Then with shouts
6150	Of triumph, vaunting, thus Idomeneus:
6150	""
6150	"""How now, Deiphobus? are three for one"
6150	An equal balance? where are now thy boasts?
6150	Come forth, my friend, thyself to me oppos'd;
6150	And learn, if here, unworthy my descent
6150	From Jove, my great progenitor, I stand.
6150	He Minos, guardian chief of Crete, begot;
6150	Noble Deucalion was to Minos born,
6150	I to Deucalion; far extends my rule
6150	In wide-spread Crete; whom now our ships have brought,
6150	"A bane to thee, thy sire, and Trojans all."""
6150	""
6150	He said; and doubtful stood Deiphobus,
6150	Or to retreat, and summon to his aid
6150	The Trojans, or alone the venture try.
6150	Thus as he mus'd, the wiser course appear'd
6150	To seek AEneas; him he found apart,
6150	Behind the crowd; for he was still at feud
6150	With godlike Priam, who, he thought, withheld
6150	The public honour to his valour due.
6150	To whom Deiphobus, approaching, thus:
6150	""
6150	"""AEneas, sagest councillor of Troy,"
6150	Behoves thee now, if rev'rence for the dead
6150	Can move thy soul, thy sister's husband aid:
6150	Haste we to save Alcathous; who of old,
6150	When thou wast little, in thy father's house,
6150	Nurs'd thee with tender care; for him, but now,
6150	"The spear-renown'd Idomeneus hath slain."""
6150	""
6150	He said; AEneas' spirit was rous'd, and fill'd
6150	With martial rage he sought Idomeneus.
6150	Nor, cowardlike, did he th' encounter shun;
6150	But firmly stood, as stands a mountain-boar
6150	Self-confident, that in some lonely spot
6150	Awaits the clam'rous chase; bristles his back;
6150	His eyes with fire are flashing; and his tusks
6150	He whets, on men and dogs prepar'd to rush:
6150	So stood the spear-renown'd Idomeneus,
6150	The onset of AEneas, swift in fight,
6150	Awaiting; and the friends he saw around
6150	He summon'd to his aid; Ascalaphus,
6150	Deipyrus, and brave Meriones,
6150	Antilochus and Aphareus; to these,
6150	Tried warriors all, he thus addressed his speech:
6150	""
6150	"""Aid me, my friends! alone I stand, and dread"
6150	The onset of AEneas, swift of foot.
6150	Mighty to slay in battle; and the bloom
6150	Of youth is his, the crown of human strength;
6150	If, as our spirit, our years were but the same,
6150	"Great glory now should he, or I, obtain."""
6150	He said; and, one in heart, their bucklers slop'd
6150	Upon their shoulders, all beside him stood.
6150	""
6150	On th' other side, AEneas to his aid
6150	Summon'd his brother chiefs, Deiphobus,
6150	And Paris, and Agenor; following whom
6150	Came on the gen'ral crowd; as flocks of sheep
6150	From pasture follow to their drinking-place
6150	The lordly ram; well pleas'd the shepherd sees;
6150	So pleas'd, AEneas saw the gath'ring crowd.
6150	Then o'er Alcathous hand to hand was wag'd
6150	The war of spears; dire was the clash of brass
6150	Upon the heroes' breasts, as 'mid the press
6150	Each aim'd at other; proudly eminent
6150	Stood forth two mighty warriors, terrible
6150	As Mars, AEneas and Idomeneus,
6150	Their sharp spears wielding each at other's life.
6150	First at Idomeneus AEneas threw
6150	His spear; he saw, and shunn'd the brazen point;
6150	And vainly from his stalwart hand dismiss'd,
6150	AEneas' spear stood quiv'ring in the ground.
6150	Idomeneus in front, below the waist,
6150	OEnomaus struck; the weighty spear broke through
6150	The hollow breastplate, and th' intestines tore;
6150	Prone in the dust he fell, and clutch'd the ground.
6150	Forthwith Idomeneus from out the corpse
6150	The pond'rous spear withdrew; yet could not strip
6150	His armour off; so thickly flew the spears.
6150	Nor did his feet retain their youthful force,
6150	His weapon to regain, or back to spring.
6150	Skill'd in the standing fight his life to guard,
6150	He lack'd the active pow'r of swift retreat.
6150	At him, retiring slow, Deiphobus,
6150	Still fill'd with anger, threw his glitt'ring spear:
6150	His aim he miss'd; but through the shoulder pierc'd
6150	Ascalaphus, a valiant son of Mars;
6150	Prone in the dust he fell, and clutch'd the ground.
6150	Nor knew the loud-voic'd, mighty God of War
6150	That in the stubborn fight his son had fall'n;
6150	On high Olympus, girt with golden clouds,
6150	He sat, amid th' Immortals all, restrain'd,
6150	By Jove's commands, from mingling in the war.
6150	How hand to hand around Ascalaphus
6150	Rag'd the fierce conflict: first Deiphobus
6150	From off his head the glitt'ring helmet tore;
6150	But, terrible as Mars, Meriones
6150	Sprang forth, and pierc'd his arm; and from his hand
6150	With hollow sound the crested helmet fell.
6150	On, like a vulture, sprang Meriones,
6150	And from his arm the sturdy spear withdrew;
6150	Then backward leap'd amid his comrades' ranks;
6150	While round his brother's waist Polites threw
6150	His arms, and led him from the battle-field
6150	To where, with charioteer and rich-wrought car,
6150	Beyond the fight, his flying coursers stood.
6150	Him, rack'd with pain, and groaning, while the blood
6150	Stream'd down his wounded arm, to Troy they bore.
6150	The rest fought on, and loud the tumult rose.
6150	""
6150	AEneas through the throat of Aphareus,
6150	Caletor's son, turn'd sideways tow'rds him, drove
6150	His glitt'ring spear; and down on th' other side,
6150	His shield and helmet following, sank his head;
6150	And o'er his eyes were cast the shades of death.
6150	As Thoon turn'd, Antilochus, who watch'd
6150	Th' occasion, forward sprang, and with his spear
6150	Ripp'd all the flesh that lay along the spine
6150	Up to the neck; he backward fell, with hands
6150	Uplifted calling for his comrades' aid:
6150	But forward sprang Antilochus, and tore
6150	His armour from his breast, while round he cast
6150	His watchful glances; for on ev'ry side
6150	On his broad shield the Trojans show'r'd their blows,
6150	But touch'd him not; for Neptune, 'mid the throng
6150	Of weapons, threw his guard o'er Nestor's son.
6150	Yet not aloof he stood, but in their midst,
6150	Commingled; nor held motionless his spear;
6150	But ever threat'ning, turn'd from side to side,
6150	Prepar'd to hurl, or hand to hand engage.
6150	Him Adamas, the son of Asius, marked,
6150	As o'er the crowd he glanc'd; and springing forth,
6150	Struck with his spear the centre of the shield;
6150	But dark-hair'd Neptune grudg'd the hero's life,
6150	And stay'd the brazen point; half in the shield,
6150	Like a fire-harden'd stake, remained infix'd,
6150	The other half lay broken, on the ground.
6150	Back to his comrades' shelt'ring ranks he sprang,
6150	In hope of safety; but Meriones,
6150	Quick-following, plung'd his weapon through his groin,
6150	Where sharpest agony to wretched men
6150	Attends on death; there planted he his spear:
6150	Around the shaft he writh'd, and gasping groan'd,
6150	Like to a mountain bull, which, bound with cords,
6150	The herdsmen drag along, with struggles vain,
6150	Resisting; so the wounded warrior groan'd:
6150	But not for long: for fierce Meriones,
6150	Approaching, from his body tore the spear,
6150	And the dark shades of death his eyes o'erspread.
6150	Then Helenus, a weighty Thracian sword
6150	Wielding aloft, across the temples smote
6150	Deipyrus, and all his helmet crash'd;
6150	Which, as it roll'd beneath their feet, some Greek
6150	Seiz'd 'mid the press; his eyes were clos'd in death.
6150	The valiant Menelaus, Atreus' son,
6150	With grief beheld; and royal Helenus
6150	With threat'ning mien approaching, pois'd on high
6150	His glitt'ring spear, while he the bowstring drew.
6150	Then simultaneous flew from either side
6150	The gleaming spear, and arrow from the string.
6150	The shaft of Priam's son below the breast
6150	The hollow cuirass struck, and bounded off;
6150	As bound the dark-skinn'd beans, or clatt'ring peas,
6150	From the broad fan upon the threshing-floor,
6150	By the brisk breeze impell'd, and winnower's force;
6150	From noble Menelaus' cuirass so
6150	The stinging arrow bounding, glanc'd afar.
6150	But valiant Menelaus, Atreus' son,
6150	Transfix'd the hand that held the polish'd bow:
6150	The brazen point pass'd through, and to the bow
6150	The hand was pinn'd; back to his comrades' ranks
6150	He sprang, in hope of safety, hanging down
6150	The wounded limb, that trail'd the ashen spear.
6150	Agenor from the wound the spear withdrew,
6150	And with a twisted sling of woollen cloth,
6150	By an attendant brought, bound up the hand.
6150	To noble Menelaus stood oppos'd
6150	Peisander, to the confines dark of death
6150	Led by his evil fate, by thee to fall,
6150	Great son of Atreus, in the deadly strife.
6150	When near they drew, Atrides miss'd his aim,
6150	With erring spear divergent; next his shield
6150	Peisander struck, but drove not through the spear;
6150	For the broad shield resisted, and the shaft
6150	Was snapp'd in sunder: Menelaus saw
6150	Rejoicing, and with hope of triumph flush'd;
6150	Unsheathing then his silver-studded sword
6150	Rush'd on Peisander; he beneath his shield
6150	Drew forth a pond'rous brazen battle-axe,
6150	With handle long, of polish'd olive-wood:
6150	And both at once in deadly combat join'd.
6150	Then, just below the plume, Peisander struck
6150	The crested helmet's peak; but Atreus' son
6150	Met him advancing, and across the brow
6150	Smote him, above the nose; loud crash'd the bone,
6150	And in the dust the gory eyeballs dropp'd
6150	Before him; doubled with the pain, he fell:
6150	The victor, planting on his chest his foot,
6150	Stripp'd off his arms, and thus exulting cried:
6150	"""Thus shall ye all, insatiate of the fight,"
6150	Proud Trojans, from before our ships depart;
6150	Nor lack your share of insult and of wrong,
6150	Such as on me, vile hounds, ye cast erewhile,
6150	Nor fear'd th' avenger of the slighted laws
6150	Of hospitality, high thund'ring Jove,
6150	Who soon your lofty city shall o'erthrow.
6150	Kindly receiv'd, my virgin-wedded wife,
6150	With store of goods, ye basely bore away;
6150	And now ye rage, infuriate, to destroy
6150	With fire our ocean-going ships, and slay
6150	Our Grecian heroes; but the time shall come
6150	When ye too fain would from the war escape.
6150	O Father Jove, 'tis said that thou excell'st,
6150	In wisdom, Gods and men; all human things
6150	From thee proceed; and can it be, that thou
6150	With favour seest these men of violence,
6150	These Trojans, with presumptuous courage fill'd,
6150	Whose rage for the battle knows nor stint nor bound?
6150	Men are with all things sated; sleep and love;
6150	Sweet sounds of music, and the joyous dance.
6150	Of these may some more gladly take their fill;
6150	"But Trojans still for war, instiate, thirst."""
6150	""
6150	Thus Menelaus; and the blood-stained arms
6150	Stripp'd from the corpse, and to his comrades gave;
6150	Then join'd again the foremost in the fray.
6150	There to th' encounter forth Harpalion sprang,
6150	Son of the King Pylaemenes, who came,
6150	His father following, to the war of Troy,
6150	But back return'd not to his native land.
6150	He standing near, full in the centre struck
6150	Atrides' shield, but drove not through the spear;
6150	Back to his comrades' shelt'ring ranks he sprang
6150	In hopes of safety, glancing all around,
6150	His body to defend; but as he turn'd,
6150	In his right flank a brazen-pointed shaft,
6150	Shot by Meriones, was buried deep:
6150	Beneath the bone it pass'd, and pierc'd him through.
6150	At once he fell; and gasping out his life,
6150	Amid his comrades, writhing on the ground
6150	Like a crush'd worm he lay; and from the wound
6150	The dark blood pouring, drench'd the thirsty soil.
6150	""
6150	The valiant troops of Paphlagonia clos'd
6150	Around him; on his car they plac'd the slain.
6150	And deeply sorrowing, to the city bore;
6150	His father, weeping, walk'd beside the car,                     [4]
6150	Nor vengeance for his slaughter'd son obtain'd.
6150	Paris with grief and anger saw him fall:
6150	For he in former days his guest had been
6150	In Paphlagonia; then, with anger fill'd,
6150	A brass-tipp'd arrow from his bow he sent.
6150	""
6150	A certain man there was, Euchenor nam'd,
6150	Who dwelt in Corinth; rich, of blameless life,
6150	The son of Polyeidus, skilful seer:
6150	His fate well knowing, he embark'd; for oft
6150	The good old man had told him that his doom
6150	Was, or at home by sharp disease to die,
6150	Or with the Greeks by Trojan hands to fall.
6150	Embarking, he escap'd alike the fine
6150	By Greeks impos'd, and pangs of sharp disease.
6150	Him Paris smote between the ear and jaw;
6150	Swift fled his spirit, and darkness clos'd his eyes.
6150	Thus rag'd, like blazing fire, the furious fight.
6150	""
6150	But nought as yet had Hector heard, nor knew
6150	How sorely, leftward of the ships, were press'd
6150	The Trojans by the Greeks; and now appear'd
6150	Their triumph, sure; such succour Neptune gave,
6150	Their courage rousing, and imparting strength.
6150	But there he kept, where first the serried ranks
6150	Of Greeks he broke, and storm'd the wall and gates;
6150	There beach'd beside the hoary sea, the ships
6150	Of Ajax and Protesilaus lay;
6150	There had the wall been lowest built; and there
6150	Were gather'd in defence the chiefest all,
6150	Horses and men: the stout Boeotians there,
6150	Join'd to th' Ionians with their flowing robes,
6150	Loerians, and Phthians, and Epeians proud,
6150	Could scarce protect their ships; nor could repel
6150	Th' impetuous fire of godlike Hector's charge.
6150	There too the choicest troops of Athens fought;
6150	Their chief, Menestheus, Peteus' son; with whom
6150	Were Pheidas, Stichius, Bias in command;
6150	Th' Epeians Meges, Phyleus' son, obey'd,
6150	And Dracius and Amphion; Medon next,
6150	With brave Podarces led the Phthian host:
6150	Medon, the great Oileus' bastard son,
6150	Brother of Ajax; he in Phylace,
6150	Far from his native land, was driv'n to dwell,
6150	Since one to Eriopis near akin,
6150	His sire Oileus' wife, his hand had slain.
6150	Podarces from Iphiclus claim'd his birth,
6150	The son of Phylacus; these two in arms
6150	The valiant Phthians leading to the fight,
6150	Join'd the Boeotian troops to guard the ships.
6150	But from the side of Ajax Telamon
6150	Stirr'd not a whit Oileus' active son;
6150	But as on fallow-land with one accord,
6150	Two dark-red oxen drag the well-wrought plough,
6150	Streaming with sweat that gathers round their horns;
6150	They by the polish'd yoke together held,
6150	The stiff soil cleaving, down the furrow strain;
6150	So closely, side by side, those two advanc'd.
6150	But comrades, many and brave, on Telamon
6150	Attended, who, whene'er with toil and sweat
6150	His limbs grew faint, upheld his weighty shield;
6150	While in the fray, Oileus' noble son
6150	No Locrians follow'd; theirs were not the hearts
6150	To brook th' endurance of the standing fight;
6150	Nor had they brass-bound helms, with horsehair plume,
6150	Nor ample shields they bore, nor ashen spear;
6150	But came to Troy, in bows and twisted slings
6150	Of woollen cloth confiding; and from these
6150	Their bolts quick-show'ring, broke the Trojan ranks.
6150	While those, in front, in glitt'ring arms oppos'd
6150	The men of Troy, by noble Hector led:
6150	These, in the rear, unseen, their arrows shot.
6150	Nor stood the Trojans; for amid their ranks
6150	The galling arrows dire confusion spread.
6150	Then had the Trojans from the ships and tents
6150	Back to the breezy heights of Troy been driv'n
6150	In flight disastrous; but Polydamas
6150	Drew near to Hector, and address'd him thus:
6150	""
6150	"""Hector, I know thee, how unapt thou art"
6150	To hearken to advice; because the Gods
6150	Have giv'n thee to excel in warlike might,
6150	Thou deemest thyself, in counsel too, supreme;
6150	Yet every gift thou canst not so combine:
6150	To one the Gods have granted warlike might,
6150	To one the dance, to one the lyre and song;
6150	While in another's breast all-seeing Jove
6150	Hath plac'd the spirit of wisdom, and a mind
6150	Discerning, for the common good of all:
6150	By him are states preserv'd; and he himself
6150	Best knows the value of the precious gift.
6150	Then hear what seems to me the wisest course.
6150	On ev'ry side the circling ring of war
6150	Is blazing all around thee; and, thou seest,
6150	Our valiant Trojans, since the wall they scal'd,
6150	Or stand aloof, or scatter'd 'mid the ships
6150	Outnumber'd, with superior forces strive.
6150	Then thou, retiring, hither call the chiefs;
6150	Here take we counsel fully, if to fall
6150	Upon their well-mann'd ships, should Heaven vouchsafe
6150	The needful strength, or, scatheless yet, withdraw;
6150	For much I fear they soon will pay us back
6150	Their debt of yesterday; since in their ranks
6150	One yet remains insatiate of the fight,
6150	"And he, methinks, not long will stand aloof."""
6150	""
6150	Thus he: the prudent counsel Hector pleas'd;
6150	Down from his chariot with his arms he leap'd,
6150	And to Polydamas his speech address'd:
6150	""
6150	"""Polydamas, detain thou here the chiefs;"
6150	Thither will I, and meet the front of war,
6150	"And, giv'n my orders, quickly here return."""
6150	""
6150	He said; and, like a snow-clad mountain high,
6150	Uprose; and loudly shouting, in hot haste
6150	Flew through the Trojan and Confed'rate host.
6150	At sound of Hector's voice, round Panthous' son,
6150	Polydamas, were gather'd all the chiefs.
6150	But 'mid the foremost combatants he sought
6150	If haply he might find Deiphobus,
6150	And royal Helenus, and Adamas,
6150	And gallant Asius, son of Hyrtacus.
6150	These found he not unscath'd by wounds or death;
6150	For some beside the ships of Greece had paid,
6150	By Grecian hands, the forfeit of their lives,
6150	While others wounded lay within the wall.
6150	But, to the leftward of the bloody fray,
6150	The godlike Paris, fair-hair'd Helen's Lord,
6150	Cheering his comrades to the fight, he found,
6150	And with reproachful words address'd him thus:
6150	""
6150	"""Thou wretched Paris, fair in outward form,"
6150	Thou slave of woman, manhood's counterfeit,
6150	Where is Deiphobus, and where the might
6150	Of royal Helenus? where Adamas,
6150	The son of Asius? where too Asius, son
6150	Of Hyrtacus? and where Othryoneus?
6150	Now from its summit totters to the fall
6150	"Our lofty Ilium; now thy doom is sure."""
6150	""
6150	To whom the godlike Paris thus replied:
6150	"""Hector, since blameless I incur thy blame,"
6150	Ne'er have I less withdrawn me from the fight,
6150	And me not wholly vile my mother bore;
6150	For since thou gav'st command to attack the ships,
6150	We here against the Greeks unflinching war
6150	Have wag'd; our comrades, whom thou seek'st, are slain:
6150	Only Deiphobus hath left the field,
6150	And Helenus; both wounded by the spear,
6150	Both through the hand; but Jove their life hath spar'd.
6150	But thou, where'er thy courage bids, lead on:
6150	We shall be prompt to follow; to our pow'r
6150	Thou shalt in us no lack of valour find;
6150	"Beyond his pow'r the bravest cannot fight."""
6150	""
6150	Wrought on his brother's mind the hero's words:
6150	Together both they bent their steps, where rag'd
6150	The fiercest conflict; there Cebriones,
6150	Phalces, Orthaeus, brave Polydamas,
6150	Palmys, and godlike Polyphetes' might,
6150	And Morys, and Ascanius fought; these two
6150	Hippotion's sons; from rich Ascania's plains
6150	They, as reliefs, but yestermorn had come;
6150	Impell'd by Jove, they sought the battle field.
6150	Onward they dash'd, impetuous as the rush
6150	Of the fierce whirlwind, which with lightning charg'd,
6150	From Father Jove sweeps downward o'er the plain:
6150	As with loud roar it mingles with the sea,
6150	The many-dashing ocean's billows boil,
6150	Upheaving, foam-white-crested, wave on wave;
6150	So, rank on rank, the Trojans, closely mass'd,
6150	In arms all glitt'ring, with their chiefs advanc'd;
6150	Hector, the son of Priam, led them on,
6150	In combat terrible as blood-stain'd Mars:
6150	Before his breast his shield's broad orb he bore,
6150	Of hides close join'd, with brazen plates o'erlaid;
6150	The gleaming helmet nodded o'er his brow.
6150	He, with proud step, protected by his shield,
6150	On ev'ry side the hostile ranks survey'd,
6150	If signs of yielding he might trace; but they
6150	Unshaken stood; and with like haughty mien,
6150	Ajax at Hector thus defiance hurl'd:
6150	""
6150	"""Draw nearer, mighty chief; why seek to scare"
6150	Our valiant Greeks? we boast ourselves of war
6150	Not wholly unskill'd, though now the hand of Jove
6150	Lies heavy on us with the scourge of Heav'n.
6150	Thou hop'st, forsooth, our vessels to destroy;
6150	But stalwart arms for their defence we boast.
6150	Long ere that day shall your proud city fall,
6150	Tak'n and destroy'd by our victorious hands.
6150	Not far the hour, when thou thyself in flight
6150	To Jove and all the Gods shalt make thy pray'r,
6150	That swifter than the falcon's wing thy steeds
6150	"May bear thee o'er the dusty plain to Troy."""
6150	""
6150	Thus as he spoke, upon his right appear'd
6150	An eagle, soaring high; the crowd of Greeks
6150	The fav'ring omen saw, and shouted loud:
6150	"Then noble Hector thus: ""What words are these,"
6150	Ajax, thou babbling braggart, vain of speech!
6150	For would to Heav'n I were as well assur'd
6150	I were the son of aegis-bearing Jove,
6150	Born of imperial Juno, and myself
6150	In equal honour with Apollo held
6150	Or blue-ey'd Pallas, as I am assur'd
6150	This day is fraught with ill to all the Greeks:
6150	Thou 'mid the rest shalt perish, if thou dare
6150	My spear encounter, which thy dainty skin
6150	Shall rend; and slain beside the ships, thy flesh
6150	"Shall glut the dogs and carrion birds of Troy."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and led them on; with eager cheers
6150	They followed; shouted loud the hindmost throng.
6150	On th' other side the Greeks return'd the shout:
6150	Of all the Trojans' bravest they, unmov'd,
6150	The onset bore; their mingled clamours rose
6150	To Heav'n, and reach'd the glorious light of Jove.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	ARGUMENT.
6150	""
6150	JUNO DECEIVES JUPITER BY THE GIRDLE OF VENUS.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	Nestor, sitting at the table with Machaon, is alarmed with the
6150	increasing clamour of the war, and hastens to Agamemnon; on his way he
6150	meets that prince with Diomed and Ulysses, whom he informs of the
6150	extremity of the danger. Agamemnon proposes to make their escape by
6150	night, which Ulysses withstands; to which Diomed adds his advice, that,
6150	wounded as they were, they should go forth and encourage the army with
6150	their presence; which advice is pursued. Juno, seeing the partiality of
6150	Jupiter to the Trojans, forms a design to overreach him; she sets off
6150	her charms with the utmost care, and (the more surely to enchant him)
6150	obtains the magic girdle of Venus. She then applies herself to the god
6150	of Sleep, and with some difficulty persuades him to seal the eyes of
6150	Jupiter; this done, she goes to Mount Ida, where the god at first
6150	sight, is ravished with her beauty, sinks in her embraces, and is laid
6150	asleep. Neptune takes advantage of his slumber, and succours the
6150	Greeks; Hector is struck to the ground with a prodigious stone by Ajax,
6150	and carried off from the battle; several actions succeed; till the
6150	Trojans, much distressed, are obliged to give way; the lesser Ajax
6150	signalizes himself in a particular manner.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	BOOK XIV.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	Nor did the battle-din not reach the ears
6150	Of Nestor, o'er the wine-cup; and his speech
6150	He thus address'd to AEsculapius' son:
6150	""
6150	"""Say, good Machaon, what these sounds may mean;"
6150	For louder swells the tumult round the ships.
6150	But sit thou here, and drink the ruddy wine,
6150	Till fair-hair'd Hecamede shall prepare
6150	The gentle bath, and wash thy gory wounds;
6150	"While I go forth, and all around survey."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and from the wall a buckler took,
6150	Well-wrought, with brass resplendent, which his son,
6150	Brave Thrasymedes, in the tent had left,
6150	While with his father's shield himself was girt;
6150	A sturdy spear too, tipp'd with brass, he took:
6150	Without the tent he stood; and there his eyes
6150	A woful sight beheld; the Greeks in flight,
6150	The haughty Trojans pressing on their rout
6150	Confus'd; the Greeks' protecting wall o'erthrown.
6150	As heaves the darkling sea with silent swell,
6150	Expectant of the boist'rous gale's approach;
6150	Nor onward either way is pour'd its flood,
6150	Until it feel th' impelling blast from Heav'n;
6150	So stood th' old man, his mind perplex'd with doubt,
6150	To mingle in the throng, or counsel seek
6150	Of mighty Agamemnon, Atreus' son.
6150	Thus as he mused, the better course appear'd,
6150	To seek Atrides; fiercely fought the rest
6150	With mutual slaughter; loud their armour rang
6150	With thrusts of swords and double-pointed spears.
6150	There Nestor met, advancing from the ships,
6150	The Heav'n-born Kings, Ulysses, Diomed,
6150	And Agamemnon, son of Atreus, all
6150	By wounds disabled; for the ships were beach'd
6150	Upon the shore, beside the hoary sea,
6150	Far from the battle; higher, tow'rd the plain
6150	The foremost had been drawn, and with a wall
6150	Their sterns surrounded; for the spacious beach
6150	Could not contain them, and in narrow bounds
6150	Were pent their multitudes; so high on land
6150	They drew, and rang'd them side by side, and fill'd,
6150	Within the headlands, all the wide-mouth'd bay.
6150	Thus they, their steps supporting on their spears,
6150	Together came, spectators of the fight;
6150	Deep sorrow fill'd their breasts; them Nestor met,
6150	The fear increasing, which their souls possess'd.
6150	To whom the monarch Agamemnon thus:
6150	""
6150	"""O Nestor, son of Neleus, pride of Greece,"
6150	Why com'st thou here, and leav'st the battle-field?
6150	Greatly I fear that noble Hector now
6150	His menace will fulfil, who made his boast
6150	Before th' assembled Trojans, that to Troy
6150	He never would return, until our ships
6150	The flames had master'd, and ourselves the sword.
6150	Such was his threat, and now he makes it good.
6150	Heav'n! can it be that I of other Greeks,
6150	As of Achilles, have incurr'd the wrath,
6150	"Who thence refuse to battle for the ships?"""
6150	""
6150	To whom Gerenian Nestor thus replied:
6150	"""Such are indeed our prospects; Jove on high"
6150	Could to our fortunes give no diff'rent turn.
6150	The wall is raz'd, wherein our trust we plac'd
6150	To guard, impregnable, ourselves and ships;
6150	And now around the ships their war they wage,
6150	Unceasing, unabated; none might tell
6150	By closest scrutiny, which way are driv'n
6150	The routed Greeks, so intermix'd they fall
6150	Promiscuous; and the cry ascends to Heav'n.
6150	But come, discuss we what may best be done,
6150	If judgment aught may profit us; ourselves
6150	To mingle in the fray I counsel not;
6150	"It were not well for wounded men to fight."""
6150	""
6150	Whom answer'd Agamemnon, King of men:
6150	"""Nestor, since to the ships the war is brought,"
6150	Nor hath the wall avail'd to stay their course,
6150	Nor yet the deep-dug trench, on which we Greeks
6150	Much toil bestow'd, and which we vainly hop'd
6150	Might guard, impregnable, ourselves and ships;
6150	Seems it the will of Saturn's mighty son
6150	That, far from Argos, from our native land,
6150	We all should here in nameless graves be laid.
6150	I knew when once he lov'd to aid the Greeks;
6150	But now I see that to the blessed Gods
6150	Our foes he equals, and our strength confounds.
6150	Hear then my counsel; let us all agree
6150	The ships that nearest to the sea are beach'd
6150	To launch upon the main, till nightfall there
6150	To ride at anchor: if that e'en by night
6150	The Trojans may suspend their fierce assault;
6150	Then may we launch in safety all the fleet.
6150	No shame it is to fly, although by night,
6150	Impending evil; better so to fly
6150	"Than by the threaten'd danger be o'erta'en."""
6150	""
6150	To whom, with scornful glance, Ulysses sage:
6150	"""What words have pass'd the barrier of thy lips,"
6150	Thou son of Atreus? counsellor of ill!
6150	Would thou hadst been of some ignoble band
6150	The leader, not the chief of such a host
6150	As ours, on whom, from youth to latest age,
6150	Jove hath the gift bestow'd, to bear the brunt
6150	Of hardy war, till ev'ry man be slain.
6150	And think'st thou so to leave the lofty walls
6150	Of Troy, the object of our painful toil?
6150	Be silent, that no other Greek may hear
6150	Words, which no man might trust his tongue to speak,
6150	Who nobler counsels understands, and wields
6150	A royal sceptre, and th' allegiance claims
6150	Of numbers, such as those that own thy sway.
6150	Thy counsels all I utterly condemn;
6150	Who, 'mid the close and clamour of the fight,
6150	Wouldst have us launch our ships, and give the foe,
6150	Already too triumphant, cause renew'd
6150	For boasting; then were death our certain lot;
6150	For, if the ships he launch'd, not long will Greeks
6150	Sustain the war, but with reverted eyes
6150	Shrink from the fight; to such pernicious end
6150	"Would lead thy baneful counsels, mighty chief."""
6150	""
6150	Whom answer'd Agamemnon, King of men:
6150	"""Ulysses, thy rebuke hath wrung my soul;"
6150	Yet never meant I, that against their will
6150	The sons of Greece should launch their well found ships:
6150	But if there be who better counsel knows,
6150	"Or young or old, his words would please me well."""
6150	""
6150	Then rose the valiant Diomed, and said:
6150	"""The man is near at hand, nor far to seek,"
6150	If ye will hear, nor take offence, that I,
6150	The youngest of you all, presume to speak.
6150	Yet of a noble sire I boast me sprung,
6150	Tydeus, who sleeps beneath the Theban soil:
6150	To Portheus three brave sons were born, who dwelt
6150	In Pleuron and in lofty Calydon,
6150	Agrius, and Melas; bravest of them all,
6150	My father's father, OEneus, was the third.
6150	He there remain'd; my father, wand'ring long,
6150	To Argos came; such was the will of Jove
6150	And of th' Immortals all; he there espous'd
6150	Adrastus' daughter; own'd a wealthy house,
6150	With fertile corn-lands round, and orchards stor'd
6150	With goodly fruit-trees; num'rous flocks he had,
6150	And all the Greeks in feats of arms excell'd.
6150	Hear ye the words I speak, for they are true:
6150	And if my speech be wise, despise it not,
6150	As of one worthless, or ignobly born.
6150	Though wounded, to the battle I advise
6150	That we perforce repair; yet not ourselves
6150	To join the combat, or confront the spears,
6150	Lest wounds to wounds be added; but to rouse
6150	The spirits of some, who, zealous heretofore,
6150	"How stand aloof, nor mingle in the fray."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and they, his words approving, went,
6150	By Agamemnon led, the King of men.
6150	Nor careless was the watch by Neptune kept:
6150	With them, in likeness of an aged man,
6150	He went, and Agamemnon, Atreus' son,
6150	By the right hand he took, and thus address'd:
6150	""
6150	"""O son of Atreus, great is now the joy"
6150	With which Achilles' savage breast is fill'd,
6150	Who sees the slaughter and the rout of Greeks:
6150	For nought he has of heart, no, not a whit:
6150	But perish he, accursed of the Gods!
6150	Nor deem thou that to thee the blessed Gods
6150	Are wholly hostile; yet again the chiefs
6150	And councillors of Troy shall scour in flight
6150	The dusty plain; and from the ships and tents
6150	"Thine eyes shall see them to the city fly."""
6150	""
6150	He said; and loudly shouting, onward rush'd.
6150	As of nine thousand or ten thousand men,
6150	In deadly combat meeting, is the shout;
6150	Such was the sound which from his ample chest
6150	Th' Earth-shaker sent; and ev'ry Greek inspir'd
6150	With stern resolve to wage unflinching war.
6150	""
6150	Standing on high Olympus' topmost peak,
6150	The golden-throned Juno downward look'd,
6150	And, busied in the glory-giving strife,
6150	Her husband's brother and her own she saw,
6150	Saw, and rejoic'd; next, seated on the crest
6150	Of spring-abounding Ida, Jove she saw,
6150	Sight hateful in her eyes! then ponder'd deep
6150	The stag-ey'd Queen, how best she might beguile
6150	The wakeful mind of aegis-bearing Jove;
6150	And, musing, this appear'd the readiest mode:
6150	Herself with art adorning, to repair
6150	To Ida; there, with fondest blandishment
6150	And female charm, her husband to enfold
6150	In love's embrace; and gentle, careless sleep
6150	Around his eyelids and his senses pour.
6150	Her chamber straight she sought, by Vulcan built,
6150	Her son; by whom were to the door-posts hung
6150	Close-fitting doors, with secret keys secur'd,
6150	That, save herself, no God might enter in.
6150	There enter'd she, and clos'd the shining doors;
6150	And with ambrosia first her lovely skin
6150	She purified, with fragrant oil anointing,
6150	Ambrosial, breathing forth such odours sweet,
6150	That, wav'd above the brazen floor of Jove,
6150	All earth and Heav'n were with the fragrance fill'd;
6150	O'er her fair skin this precious oil she spread;
6150	Comb'd out her flowing locks, and with her hand
6150	Wreath'd the thick masses of the glossy hair,
6150	Immortal, bright, that crown'd th' imperial head.
6150	A robe ambrosial then, by Pallas wrought,
6150	She donn'd, in many a curious pattern trac'd,
6150	With golden brooch beneath her breast confin'd.
6150	Her zone, from which a hundred tassels hung,
6150	She girt about her; and, in three bright drops,
6150	Her glitt'ring gems suspended from her ears;
6150	And all around her grace and beauty shone.
6150	Then o'er her head th' imperial Goddess threw
6150	A beauteous veil, new-wrought, as sunlight white;
6150	And on her well-turn'd feet her sandals bound.
6150	Her dress completed, from her chamber forth
6150	She issued, and from th' other Gods apart
6150	She call'd to Venus, and address'd her thus:
6150	"""Say, wilt thou grant, dear child, the boon I ask?"
6150	Or wilt thou say me nay, in wrath that I
6150	"Espouse the Greek, as thou the Trojan cause?"""
6150	""
6150	To whom the laughter-loving Venus thus:
6150	"""Daughter of Saturn, Juno, mighty Queen,"
6150	Tell me thy wish; to grant it if my pow'r
6150	"May aught avail, thy pleasure shall be done."""
6150	""
6150	To whom great Juno thus, with artful speech:
6150	"""Give me the loveliness, and pow'r to charm,"
6150	Whereby thou reign'st o'er Gods and men supreme.
6150	For to the bounteous Earth's extremest bounds
6150	I go, to visit old Oceanus,
6150	The sire of Gods, and Tethys, who of yore
6150	From Rhaea took me, when all-seeing Jove
6150	Hurl'd Saturn down below the earth and seas,
6150	And nurs'd me in their home with tend'rest care;
6150	I go to visit them, and reconcile
6150	A lengthen'd feud; for since some cause of wrath
6150	Has come between them, they from rites of love
6150	And from the marriage-bed have long abstain'd:
6150	Could I unite them by persuasive words,
6150	And to their former intercourse restore,
6150	"Their love and rev'rence were for ever mine."""
6150	""
6150	Whom answer'd thus the laughter-loving Queen:
6150	"""I ought not, and I cannot, say thee nay,"
6150	"Who liest encircled by the arms of Jove."""
6150	""
6150	Thus Venus spoke; and from her bosom loos'd
6150	Her broider'd cestus, wrought with ev'ry charm
6150	To win the heart; there Love, there young Desire,
6150	There fond Discourse, and there Persuasion dwelt,
6150	Which oft enthralls the mind of wisest men.
6150	This in her hand she plac'd, as thus she spoke:
6150	"""Take thou from me, and in thy bosom hide,"
6150	This broider'd cestus; and, whate'er thy wish,
6150	"Thou shalt not here ungratified return."""
6150	""
6150	Thus Venus; smil'd the stag-ey'd Queen of Heav'n,
6150	And, smiling, in her bosom hid the gift.
6150	Then Venus to her father's house return'd;
6150	But Juno down from high Olympus sped;
6150	O'er sweet Emathia, and Pieria's range,
6150	O'er snowy mountains of horse-breeding Thrace,
6150	Their topmost heights, she soar'd, nor touch'd the earth.
6150	From Athos then she cross'd the swelling sea,
6150	Until to Lemnos, godlike Thoas' seat,
6150	She came; there met she Sleep, twin-born with Death,
6150	Whom, as his hand she clasp'd, she thus address'd:
6150	""
6150	"""Sleep, universal King of Gods and men,"
6150	If ever thou hast listen'd to my voice,
6150	Grant me the boon which now I ask, and win
6150	My ceaseless favour in all time to come.
6150	When Jove thou seest in my embraces lock'd,
6150	Do thou his piercing eyes in slumber seal.
6150	Rich guerdon shall be thine; a gorgeous throne,
6150	Immortal, golden; which my skilful son,
6150	Vulcan, shall deftly frame; beneath, a stool
6150	"Whereon at feasts thy feet may softly rest."""
6150	""
6150	Whom answer'd thus the gentle God of Sleep:
6150	"""Daughter of Saturn, Juno, mighty Queen,"
6150	On any other of th' immortal Gods
6150	I can with ease exert my slumb'rous pow'r;
6150	Even to the stream of old Oceanus,
6150	Prime origin of all; but Saturn's son,
6150	Imperial Jove, I dare not so approach,
6150	Nor sink in sleep, save by his own desire.
6150	Already once, obeying thy command,
6150	A fearful warning I receiv'd, that day
6150	When from the capture and the sack of Troy
6150	That mighty warrior, son of Jove, set sail;
6150	For, circumfus'd around, with sweet constraint
6150	I bound the sense of aegis-bearing Jove,
6150	While thou, with ill-design, rousing the force
6150	Of winds tempestuous o'er the stormy sea,
6150	Didst cast him forth on Coos' thriving isle,
6150	Far from his friends; then Jove, awaking, pour'd
6150	His wrath, promiscuous, on th' assembled Gods;
6150	Me chief his anger sought; and from on high
6150	Had hurl'd me, plung'd beneath th' unfathom'd sea,
6150	But Night, the vanquisher of Gods and men,
6150	Her fugitive received me; he his wrath
6150	Repress'd, unwilling to invade the claims
6150	Of holy Night; and now thou fain wouldst urge
6150	"That I another reckless deed essay."""
6150	""
6150	Whom answer'd thus the stag-ey'd Queen of Heav'n:
6150	"""Why, Sleep, with thoughts like these perplex thy mind"
6150	Think'st thou that Jove as ardently desires
6150	To aid the men of Troy, as fiercely burn'd
6150	His anger on his valiant son's behalf?
6150	Grant my request; and of the Graces one,
6150	The youngest and the fairest, have to wife,
6150	"Pasithea, whom thy love hath long pursued."""
6150	""
6150	Thus promis'd Juno; Sleep, rejoicing, heard,
6150	"And answer'd thus: ""Swear then the awful oath."
6150	Inviolable, by the stream of Styx,
6150	Thy one hand laid upon the fruitful earth,
6150	The other resting on the sparkling sea;
6150	That all the Gods who in the nether realms
6150	With Saturn dwell, may of our solemn bond
6150	Be witnesses, that of the Graces one,
6150	The youngest, fairest, I shall have to wife,
6150	"Pasithea, whom my love hath long pursued."""
6150	""
6150	He said: nor did the white-arm'd Queen refuse;
6150	She took the oath requir'd; and call'd by name
6150	On all the Titans, sub-Tartarean Gods:
6150	Then, sworn and ratified the oath, they pass'd
6150	From Lemnos, and from Imbros, veil'd in cloud,
6150	Skimming their airy way; on Lectum first,
6150	In spring-abounding Ida, nurse of beasts,
6150	The sea they left, and journey'd o'er the land,
6150	While wav'd beneath their feet the lofty woods.
6150	There Sleep, ere yet he met the eye of Jove,
6150	Remain'd; and, mounted on a lofty pine,
6150	The tallest growth of Ida, that on high
6150	Flung through the desert air its boughs to Heav'n,
6150	Amid the pine's close branches lay ensconc'd;
6150	Like to a mountain bird of shrillest note,
6150	Whom Gods the Chalcis, men the night-hawk call.
6150	Juno meanwhile to Ida's summit sped,
6150	To Gargarus; the Cloud-compeller saw;
6150	He saw, and sudden passion fir'd his soul,
6150	As when, their parents' eyes eluding, first
6150	They tasted of the secret joys of love.
6150	He rose to meet her, and address'd her thus:
6150	""
6150	"""From high Olympus, Juno, whither bound,"
6150	And how, to Ida hast thou come in haste?
6150	"For horses here or chariot hast thou none."""
6150	""
6150	To whom thus Juno with deceitful speech
6150	"Replied: ""To fertile earth's extremest bounds"
6150	I go, to visit old Oceanus,
6150	The sire of Gods, and Tethys, who of yore
6150	Receiv'd, and nurtur'd me with tend'rest care.
6150	I go to visit them, and reconcile
6150	A lengthen'd feud; for since some cause of wrath
6150	Has come between them, they from rites of love
6150	And from the marriage-bed have long abstain'd.
6150	Meanwhile at spring-abounding Ida's foot
6150	My horses wait me, that o'er land and sea
6150	Alike my chariot bear; on thine account
6150	From high Olympus hither have I come,
6150	Lest it displease thee, if, to thee unknown,
6150	"I sought the Ocean's deeply-flowing stream."""
6150	To whom the Cloud-compeller thus replied:
6150	"""Juno, thy visit yet awhile defer;"
6150	And let us now in love's delights indulge:
6150	For never yet did such a flood of love
6150	For Goddess or for mortal fill my soul;
6150	Not for Ixion's beauteous wife, who bore
6150	Pirithous, sage in council as the Gods;
6150	Nor the neat-footed maiden Danae,
6150	Acrisius' daughter, her who Perseus bore,
6150	Th' observ'd of all; nor noble Phoenix' child,
6150	Who bore me Minos, and the godlike might
6150	Of Rhadamanthus; nor for Semele,
6150	Nor for Alcmena fair, of whom was born
6150	In Thebes the mighty warrior Hercules,
6150	As Bacchus, joy of men, of Semele:
6150	No, nor for Ceres, golden-tressed Queen,
6150	Nor for Latona bright, nor for thyself,
6150	"As now with fond desire for thee I burn."""
6150	""
6150	To whom thus Juno with deceitful speech:
6150	"""What words, dread son of Saturn, dost thou speak?"
6150	If here on Ida, in the face of day,
6150	We celebrate the mystic rites of love.
6150	How if some other of th' immortal Gods
6150	Should find us sleeping, and 'mid all the Gods
6150	Should spread the tale abroad? I could not then
6150	Straight to thy house, for very shame, return.
6150	But if indeed such passion fill thy soul,
6150	Thou hast thy secret chamber, built for thee
6150	By Vulcan, with close-fitting doors secur'd;
6150	"Thither, if such thy pleasure, go we now."""
6150	""
6150	To whom the Cloud-compeller thus replied:
6150	"""Juno, nor fear the eye of God or man;"
6150	For all around us I will throw such veil
6150	Of golden cloud, that not the sun himself
6150	"With sharpest beam of light may pierce it through."""
6150	""
6150	Thus saying, in his arms he clasp'd his wife;
6150	The teeming earth beneath them caus'd to spring
6150	The tender grass, and lotus dew-besprent,
6150	Crocus and hyacinth, a fragrant couch,
6150	Profuse and soft, upspringing from the earth.
6150	There lay they, all around them spread a veil
6150	Of golden cloud, whence heav'nly dews distill'd.
6150	There on the topmost height of Gargarus,
6150	By sleep and love subdued, th' immortal Sire,
6150	Clasp'd in his arms his wife, repos'd in peace.
6150	""
6150	Then Sleep arose, and to the Grecian ships
6150	In haste repairing, to th' Earth-shaking King
6150	His tidings bore; and standing at his side
6150	Thus to the God his winged words address'd:
6150	""
6150	"""Now, Neptune, to the Greeks thy ready aid"
6150	Afford, that short-liv'd triumph they may gain,
6150	While slumber holds the eyes of Jove; for I
6150	In sweet unconsciousness have drown'd his sense,
6150	"Beguil'd by Juno, in whose arms he lies."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and vanish'd 'mid the tribes of men:
6150	But fir'd with keener zeal to aid the Greeks,
6150	Neptune sprang forth in front, and call'd aloud:
6150	""
6150	"""Again, ye Greeks, shall our remissness yield"
6150	The victory to Hector, Priam's son,
6150	To seize our ships, and endless glory gain?
6150	Such is his boast and menace, since in wrath
6150	Achilles still beside his ships remains.
6150	Yet him we scarce should miss, if we, the rest,
6150	But firmly stood for mutual defence.
6150	Hear then my counsel: let us all agree,
6150	Girt with our best and broadest shields, our heads
6150	With flashing helmets guarded, in our hands
6150	Grasping our longest spears, to dare the fight.
6150	Myself will lead you on; and Priam's son,
6150	Though bold he be, will fear with me to cope.
6150	And if, among our bravest, any bear
6150	Too small a buckler, with some meaner man
6150	"Let him exchange, and don the larger shield."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and they assenting heard his speech.
6150	The Kings themselves, Ulysses, Diomed,
6150	And mighty Agamemnon, Atreus' son,
6150	Though sorely wounded, yet the troops array'd;
6150	Thro'out the ranks they pass'd, and chang'd the arms;
6150	The bravest donn'd the best, the worse the worst.
6150	When with their dazzling armour all were girt,
6150	Forward they mov'd; th' Earth-shaker led them on:
6150	In his broad hand an awful sword he bore,
6150	Long-bladed, vivid as the lightning's flash:
6150	Yet in the deadly strife he might not join,
6150	But kindled terror in the minds of men.
6150	""
6150	Hector meantime the Trojan troops array'd.
6150	Then fiercer grew, and more intense the strain
6150	Of furious fight, when Ocean's dark-hair'd King
6150	And Priam's noble son were met in arms,
6150	And aided, this the Trojans, that the Greeks.
6150	High tow'rd the tents uprose the surging sea,
6150	As with loud clamour met th' opposing hosts.
6150	Less loud the roar of Ocean's wave, that driv'n
6150	By stormy Boreas, breaks upon the beach;
6150	Less loud the crackling of the flames that rage
6150	In the deep forest of some mountain glen;
6150	Less loud the wind, to wildest fury rous'd,
6150	Howls in the branches of the lofty oaks;
6150	Than rose the cry of Trojans and of Greeks,
6150	As each, with furious shout, encounter'd each.
6150	At Ajax first, who straight before him stood,
6150	Great Hector threw his spear, nor miss'd his aim,
6150	Where the two belts, the one which bore his shield,
6150	His silver-studded sword the other, met
6150	Across his breast; these two his life preserv'd.
6150	Hector was wroth, that from his stalwart hand
6150	The spear had flown in vain; and back he sprang
6150	For safety to his comrades' shelt'ring ranks:
6150	But mighty Ajax Telamon upheav'd
6150	A pond'rous stone, of many, all around
6150	That scatter'd lay beneath the warriors' feet,
6150	And serv'd to prop the ships; with one of these,
6150	As Hector backward stepp'd, above the shield
6150	He smote him on the breast, below the throat.
6150	With whirling motion, circling as it flew,
6150	The mass he hurl'd. As by the bolt of Heav'n
6150	Uprooted, prostrate lies some forest oak;
6150	The sulph'rous vapour taints the air; appall'd,
6150	Bereft of strength, the near beholder stands,
6150	And awestruck hears the thunder-peal of Jove;
6150	So in the dust the might of Hector lay:
6150	Dropp'd from his hand the spear; the shield and helm
6150	Fell with him; loud his polished armour rang.
6150	On rush'd, with joyous shout, the sons of Greece,
6150	In hope to seize the spoil; thick flew the spears:
6150	Yet none might reach or wound the fallen chief;
6150	For gather'd close around, the bravest all,
6150	Valiant AEneas, and Polydamas,
6150	Godlike Agenor, and the Lycian chief
6150	Sarpedon, and the noble Glaucus stood.
6150	Nor did the rest not aid; their shields' broad orbs
6150	Before him still they held, while in their arms
6150	His comrades bore him from the battle-field,
6150	To where, with charioteer and well-wrought car,
6150	Beyond the fight, his flying coursers stood,
6150	Which bore him, deeply groaning, tow'rd the town.
6150	But when the ford was reach'd of Xanthus' stream,
6150	Broad-flowing, eddying, by immortal Jove
6150	Begotten, on the ground they laid him down,
6150	And dash'd the cooling water on his brow:
6150	Reviv'd, he lifted up awhile his eyes;
6150	Then on his knees half rising, he disgorg'd
6150	The clotted blood; but backward to the earth,
6150	Still by the blow subdu'd, again he fell,
6150	And darkling shades of night his eyes o'erspread.
6150	""
6150	Onward, with zeal redoubled, press'd the Greeks,
6150	When Hector from the field they saw withdrawn.
6150	Foremost of all, Oileus' active son,
6150	With sudden spring assailing, Satnius slew:
6150	Him a fair Naiad nymph to OEnops bore,
6150	Who by the banks of Satnois kept his herds.
6150	Him then, approaching near, Oileus' son
6150	Thrust through the flank: he fell, and o'er his corpse
6150	Trojans and Greeks in stubborn fight engag'd.
6150	But Panthous' son a swift avenger came,
6150	Polydamas, with brandish'd spear, and struck
6150	Through the right shoulder Prothoenor, son
6150	Of Areilycus; right through was driv'n
6150	The sturdy spear; he, rolling in the dust,
6150	Clutch'd with his palms the ground; then, shouting loud,
6150	Thus with triumphant boast Polydamas:
6150	""
6150	"""From the strong hand of Panthous' noble son"
6150	Methinks that not in vain the spear has flown:
6150	A Greek now bears it off; and he, perchance,
6150	"May use it as a staff to Pluto's realm."""
6150	""
6150	Thus he; the Greeks with pain his vaunting heard;
6150	But chief it rous'd the spirit within the breast
6150	Of Ajax Telamon, whom close beside
6150	The dead had fall'n; he at Polydamas,
6150	Retreating, hurl'd in haste his glitt'ring spear;
6150	He, springing sideways, 'scap'd the stroke of fate;
6150	But young Archilochus, Antenor's son,
6150	Receiv'd the spear, for Heav'n had will'd his death:
6150	The spine it struck, the topmost joint, where met
6150	The head and neck, and both the tendons broke;
6150	Forward he fell; and ere or knee or leg,
6150	His head, and mouth, and nostrils struck the ground.
6150	""
6150	Then Ajax, in his turn, exulting, thus:
6150	"""Say now, Polydamas, and tell me true,"
6150	May this be deem'd for Prothoenor's death
6150	A full equivalent? no common man
6150	He seems, and born of no ignoble race;
6150	Valiant Antenor's brother, or perchance
6150	"His son; the likeness speaks him near akin."""
6150	""
6150	Thus he, though well he knew; then bitter grief
6150	Possess'd the Trojans' souls; but Acamas,
6150	Guarding his brother's body, with his spear
6150	Slew the Boeotian Promachus, who fain
6150	Would by the feet have drawn away the dead:
6150	Then Acamas, exulting, cried aloud:
6150	""
6150	"""Ye wretched Greeks, in boasting measureless!"
6150	Not ours alone the labour and the loss
6150	Of battle; ye too have your share of death.
6150	Behold where lies your Promachus, subdued
6150	Beneath my spear; not long unpaid the debt
6150	Due for my brother's blood! 'Tis well for him
6150	"Who leaves a brother to avenge his fate."""
6150	""
6150	Thus he; the Greeks with pain his vaunting heard;
6150	But chief it rous'd the spirit within the breast
6150	Of Peneleus; on Acamas he sprang,
6150	Who waited not th' encounter; next he slew
6150	Ilioneus, the son of Phorbas, Lord
6150	Of num'rous flocks, of all the Trojans most
6150	Belov'd of Hermes, who his wealth increas'd.
6150	To him Ilioneus, an only son,
6150	His mother bore; who now, beneath the brow
6150	And through the socket of the eye was struck,
6150	Thrusting the eyeball out; for through the eye,
6150	And backward through the head, the spear was driv'n:
6150	With hands extended, down to earth he sank;
6150	But Peneleus his weighty sword let fall
6150	Full on his neck; the sever'd head and helm
6150	Together fell, remaining still infix'd
6150	The sturdy spear; then he, the gory head
6150	Uplifting, to the Trojans vaunting cried:
6150	""
6150	"""Go now, ye Trojans! bid that in the house"
6150	Of brave Ilioneus his parents raise
6150	The voice of wailing for their gallant son;
6150	As neither shall the wife of Promachus,
6150	The son of Alegenor, with glad smile
6150	Her husband's coming hail, when home from Troy
6150	"We sons of Greece, with vict'ry crown'd, return."""
6150	""
6150	Thus as he spoke, pale fear possess'd them all,
6150	Each looking round to seek escape from death.
6150	""
6150	Say now, ye Nine, who on Olympus dwell,
6150	Who, when th' Earth-shaker turn'd the tide of war,
6150	First bore away his foeman's bloody spoils?
6150	""
6150	Great Ajax Telamon first Hyrtius smote,
6150	The son of Gyrtius, who to battle led
6150	The warlike Mysians; next Antilochus
6150	From Mermerus and Phalces stripp'd their arms;
6150	Meriones Hippotion gave to death,
6150	And Morys; Teucer Periphetes slew,
6150	And Prothoon; Menelaus, through the flank
6150	Smote Hyperenor; as the grinding spear
6150	Drain'd all his vitals, through the gaping wound
6150	His spirit escap'd, and darkness clos'd his eyes.
6150	But chiefest slaughter of the Trojans wrought
6150	Oileus' active son; of all the Greeks
6150	No foot so swift as his, when Jove had fill'd
6150	Their souls with fear, to chase the flying foe.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	ARGUMENT.
6150	""
6150	THE FIFTH BATTLE, AT THE SHIPS; AND THE ACTS OF AJAX.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	Jupiter, awaking, sees the Trojans repulsed from the trenches, Hector
6150	in a swoon, and Neptune at the head of the Greeks; he is highly
6150	incensed at the artifice of Juno, who appeases him by her submissions;
6150	she is then sent to Iris and Apollo. Juno, repairing to the assembly of
6150	the gods, attempts with extraordinary address to incense them against
6150	Jupiter; in particular she touches Mars with a violent resentment; he
6150	is ready to take arms, but is prevented by Minerva. Iris and Apollo
6150	obey the orders of Jupiter; Iris commands Neptune to leave the battle,
6150	to which, after much reluctance and passion, he consents. Apollo
6150	reinspires Hector with vigour, brings him back to the battle, marches
6150	before him with his aegis, and turns the fortune of the fight. He
6150	breaks down the first part of the Grecian wall; the Trojans rush in,
6150	and attempt to fire the first line of the fleet, but are yet repelled
6150	by the greater Ajax with a prodigious slaughter.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	BOOK XV.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	Now when the Trojans had recross'd the trench
6150	And palisades, and in their headlong flight
6150	Many had fall'n by Grecian swords, the rest,
6150	Routed, and pale with fear, made head awhile
6150	Beside their cars; then Jove on Ida's height
6150	At golden-throned Juno's side awoke;
6150	Rising, he saw the Trojans and the Greeks,
6150	Those in confusion, while behind them press'd
6150	The Greeks, triumphant, Neptune in their midst:
6150	He saw too Hector stretch'd upon the plain,
6150	His comrades standing round; senseless he lay,
6150	Drawing short breath, blood gushing from his mouth;
6150	For by no feeble hand the blow was dealt.
6150	""
6150	Pitying, the Sire of Gods and men beheld,
6150	And thus, with sternest glance, to Juno spoke:
6150	"""This, Juno, is thy work! thy wicked wiles"
6150	Have Hector quell'd, and Trojans driv'n to flight:
6150	Nor know I but thyself mayst reap the fruit,
6150	By shameful scourging, of thy vile deceit.
6150	Hast thou forgotten how in former times
6150	I hung thee from on high, and to thy feet
6150	Attach'd two pond'rous anvils, and thy hands
6150	With golden fetters bound, which none might break?
6150	There didst thou hang amid the clouds of Heav'n;
6150	Through all Olympus' breadth the Gods were wroth;
6150	Yet dar'd not one approach to set thee free.
6150	If any so had ventur'd, him had I
6150	Hurl'd from Heav'n's threshold till to earth he fell,
6150	With little left of life. Yet was not quench'd
6150	My wrath on godlike Hercules' account,
6150	Whom thou, with Boreas, o'er the wat'ry waste
6150	With fell intent didst send; and tempest-toss'd,
6150	Cast him ashore on Coos' fruitful isle.
6150	I rescued him from thence, and brought him back,
6150	After long toil, to Argos' grassy plains.
6150	This to thy mind I bring, that thou mayst learn
6150	To cease thy treach'rous wiles, nor hope to gain
6150	By all thy lavish'd blandishments of love,
6150	"Wherewith thou hast deceived me, and betray'd."""
6150	""
6150	He said; and terror seiz'd the stag-ey'd Queen;
6150	Who thus with winged words address'd her Lord:
6150	""
6150	"""By Earth I swear, and yon broad Heav'n above,"
6150	And Stygian stream beneath, the weightiest oath
6150	Of solemn pow'r to bind the blessed Gods;
6150	By thine own sacred head, our nuptial bed,
6150	Whose holy tie I never could forswear;
6150	That not by my suggestion and advice
6150	Earth-shaking Neptune on the Trojan host,
6150	And Hector, pours his wrath, and aids the Greeks;
6150	In this he but obeys his own desire,
6150	Who looks with pity on the Grecian host
6150	Beside their ships o'erborne; and could my words
6150	Prevail, my counsel were to shape his course,
6150	"O cloud-girt King, obedient to thy will."""
6150	""
6150	She said; the Sire of Gods and men, well pleas'd,
6150	Her answer heard, and thus with gracious smile:
6150	""
6150	"""If, stag-ey'd Queen, in synod of the Gods"
6150	Thy counsels shall indeed with mine agree,
6150	Neptune, how strong soe'er his wish, must change
6150	His course, obedient to thy will and mine;
6150	And if in all sincerity thou speak,
6150	Go to th' assembled Gods, and hither send
6150	Iris, and Phoebus of the silver bow;
6150	That she may to the Grecian camp repair,
6150	And bid that Neptune from the battle-field
6150	Withdraw, and to his own domain retire;
6150	While Phoebus Hector to the fight restores,
6150	Inspiring new-born vigour, and allaying
6150	The mortal pains which bow his spirit down:
6150	Then, heartless fear infusing in the Greeks,
6150	Put them to flight, that flying they may fall
6150	Beside Achilles' ships; his comrade then,
6150	Patroclus, he shall send to battle forth
6150	To be by Hector slain, in front of Troy;
6150	Yet not to fall till many valiant youths
6150	Have felt his prowess; and, amid the rest,
6150	My son, Sarpedon; by his comrade's death
6150	Enrag'd, Achilles Hector shall subdue;
6150	Thenceforth my counsel is, that from the ships
6150	The Trojan force shall still be backward driv'n,
6150	Until at length, by Pallas' deep designs,
6150	The Greeks possess the lofty walls of Troy.
6150	Yet will not I my anger intermit,
6150	Nor suffer other of th' immortal Gods
6150	To aid the Greeks, till Peleus' son behold
6150	His wish accomplish'd, and the boon obtain'd
6150	I promis'd once, and with a nod confirm'd,
6150	That day when sea-born Thetis clasp'd my knees,
6150	"And pray'd me to avenge her warrior son."""
6150	""
6150	Thus he; the white-arm'd Queen of Heav'n submiss
6150	His mandate heard; and from th' Idaean mount
6150	With rapid flight to high Olympus sped.
6150	Swift as the mind of man, who many a land
6150	Hath travell'd o'er, and with reflective thought
6150	"Recalls, ""here was I such a day, or here,"""
6150	And in a moment many a scene surveys;
6150	So Juno sped o'er intervening space;
6150	Olympus' heights she reach'd, and in the house
6150	Of Jove appear'd amid th' assembled Gods.
6150	They at her coming rose, with golden cups
6150	Greeting their Queen's approach; the rest she pass'd,
6150	And from the hand of fair-fac'd Themis took
6150	The proffer'd cup, who first had run to meet,
6150	And thus with winged words address'd the Queen:
6150	"""Juno, why com'st thou hither? and with looks"
6150	Of one distraught with, fear? hath Saturn's son,
6150	"Thy mighty Lord, thus sore affrighted thee?"""
6150	To whom the white-arm'd Goddess, Juno, thus:
6150	""
6150	"""Forbear thy questions, Themis; well thou know'st"
6150	How haughty and imperious is his mind;
6150	Thou for the Gods in haste prepare the feast;
6150	Then shalt thou learn, amid th' Immortals all,
6150	What evil he designs; nor all, I ween,
6150	His counsels will approve, or men, or Gods,
6150	"Though now in blissful ignorance they feast."""
6150	""
6150	She said, and sat; the Gods, oppress'd with care,
6150	Her farther speech awaited; on her lips
6150	There dwelt indeed a smile, but not a ray
6150	Pass'd o'er her dark'ning brow, as thus her wrath
6150	Amid th' assembled Gods found vent in words:
6150	""
6150	"""Fools are we all, who madly strive with Jove,"
6150	Or hope, by access to his throne, to sway,
6150	By word or deed, his course; from all apart,
6150	He all our counsels heeds not, but derides;
6150	And boasts o'er all th' immortal Gods to reign
6150	In unapproach'd pre-eminence of pow'r.
6150	Prepare then each his sev'ral woe to bear;
6150	On Mars e'en now, methinks, the blow hath fall'n;
6150	Since in the fight, the man he loves the best,
6150	"And boasts his son, Ascalaphus, is slain."""
6150	She said; and Mars, enrag'd, his brawny thigh
6150	Smote with his hands, and thus, lamenting, spoke:
6150	""
6150	"""Blame not, ye Gods, who on Olympus dwell,"
6150	That to the Grecian ships I haste, to avenge
6150	My slaughter'd son, though blasted by Heav'n's fire
6150	"'Twere mine 'mid corpses, blood, and dust to lie."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and gave command to Fear and Flight
6150	To yoke his ear; and donn'd his glitt'ring arms.
6150	Then from the throne of Jove had heavier wrath
6150	And deeper vengeance on th' Immortals fall'n,
6150	But Pallas, in alarm for all the Gods,
6150	Quitting in haste the throne whereon she sat,
6150	Sprang past the vestibule, and from his head
6150	The helmet lifted, from his arm the shield;
6150	Took from his sturdy hand, and rear'd upright,
6150	The brazen spear; then with reproachful words
6150	She thus assail'd th' impetuous God of War;
6150	""
6150	"""Frantic, and passion-maddened, thou art lost!"
6150	Hast thou no ears to hear! or are thy mind
6150	And sense of rev'rence utterly destroyed?
6150	Or heard'st thou not what white-arm'd Juno spoke,
6150	Fresh from the presence of Olympian Jove?
6150	Wouldst thou, thine evil destiny fulfill'd,
6150	By hard constraint, despite thy grief, be driv'n
6150	Back to Olympus; and to all the rest
6150	Confusion and disaster with thee bring?
6150	At once from valiant Trojans and from Greeks
6150	His thoughts would be diverted, and his wrath
6150	Embroil Olympus, and on all alike,
6150	Guilty or not, his anger would be pour'd.
6150	Waive then thy vengeance for thy gallant son;
6150	Others as brave of heart, as strong of arm,
6150	Have fall'n, and yet must fall; and vain th' attempt
6150	"To watch at once o'er all the race of men."""
6150	""
6150	Thus saying, to his seat again she forc'd
6150	Th' impetuous Mars: meanwhile, without the house,
6150	Juno, by Jove's command, Apollo call'd,
6150	And Iris, messenger from God to God;
6150	And thus to both her winged words address'd:
6150	""
6150	"""Jove bids you with all speed to Ida haste;"
6150	And when, arriv'd, before his face ye stand,
6150	"Whate'er he orders, that observe and do."""
6150	""
6150	Thus Juno spoke, and to her throne return'd;
6150	While they to spring-abounding Ida's heights,
6150	Wild nurse of forest beasts, pursued their way;
6150	Th' all-seeing son of Saturn there they found
6150	Upon the topmost crag of Gargarus,
6150	An incense-breathing cloud around him spread.
6150	Before the face of cloud-compelling Jove
6150	They stood; well-pleas'd he witness'd their approach
6150	In swift obedience to his consort's words,
6150	And thus to Iris first his speech address'd:
6150	""
6150	"""Haste thee, swift Iris, and to Ocean's King"
6150	My message bear, nor misreporting aught,
6150	Nor aught omitting; from the battle-field
6150	Bid him retire, and join th' assembled Gods,
6150	Or to his own domain of sea withdraw.
6150	If my commands he heed not, nor obey,
6150	Let him consider in his inmost soul
6150	If, mighty though he be, he dare await
6150	My hostile coming; mightier far than him,
6150	His elder born; nor may his spirit aspire
6150	"To rival me, whom all regard with awe."""
6150	""
6150	He said; swift-footed Iris, at the word,
6150	From Ida's heights to sacred Ilium sped.
6150	Swift as the snow-flakes from the clouds descend,
6150	Or wintry hail before the driving blast
6150	Of Boreas, ether-born; so swift to Earth
6150	Descended Iris; by his side she stood,
6150	And with these words th' Earth-shaking God address'd:
6150	"""A message, dark-hair'd Circler of the Earth,"
6150	To thee I bring from AEgis-bearing Jove.
6150	He bids thee straightway from the battle-field
6150	Retire, and either join th' assembled Gods,
6150	Or to thine own domain of sea withdraw.
6150	If his commands thou heed not, nor obey,
6150	Hither he menaces himself to come,
6150	And fight against thee; but he warns thee first,
6150	Beware his arm, as mightier far than thee,
6150	Thine elder born; nor may thy spirit aspire
6150	"To rival him, whom all regard with awe."""
6150	""
6150	To whom in tow'ring wrath th' Earth-shaking God:
6150	"""By Heav'n, though great he be, he yet presumes"
6150	Somewhat too far, if me, his equal born,
6150	He seeks by force to baffle of my will.
6150	We were three brethren, all of Rhaea born
6150	To Saturn; Jove and I, and Pluto third,
6150	Who o'er the nether regions holds his sway.
6150	Threefold was our partition; each obtain'd
6150	His meed of honour due; the hoary Sea
6150	By lot my habitation was assign'd;
6150	The realms of Darkness fell to Pluto's share;
6150	Broad Heav'n, amid the sky and clouds, to Jove;
6150	But Earth, and high Olympus, are to all
6150	A common heritage; nor will I walk
6150	To please the will of Jove; though great he be,
6150	With his own third contented let him rest:
6150	Nor let him think that I, as wholly vile,
6150	Shall quail before his arm; his lofty words
6150	Were better to his daughters and his sons
6150	Address'd, his own begotten; who perforce
6150	"Must listen to his mandates, and obey."""
6150	""
6150	To whom swift-footed Iris thus replied:
6150	"""Is this, then, dark-hair'd Circler of the Earth,"
6150	The message, stern and haughty, which to Jove
6150	Thou bidd'st me bear? perchance thine angry mood
6150	May bend to better counsels; noblest minds
6150	Are easiest bent; and o'er superior age
6150	"Thou know'st th' avenging Furies ever watch."""
6150	""
6150	To whom Earth-shaking Neptune thus replied:
6150	"""Immortal Iris, weighty are thy words,"
6150	And in good season spoken; and 'tis well
6150	When envoys are by sound discretion led.
6150	Yet are my heart and mind with grief oppress'd,
6150	When me, his equal both by birth and fate,
6150	He seeks with haughty words to overbear.
6150	I yield, but with indignant sense of wrong.
6150	This too I say, nor shall my threat be vain:
6150	Let him remember, if in my despite,
6150	'Gainst Pallas', Juno's, Hermes', Vulcan's will,
6150	He spare to overthrow proud Ilium's tow'rs,
6150	And crown with victory the Grecian arms,
6150	"The feud between us never can be heal'd."""
6150	""
6150	Th' Earth-shaker said, and from the field withdrew
6150	Beneath the ocean wave, the warrior Greeks
6150	His loss deploring; to Apollo then
6150	The Cloud-compeller thus his speech address'd:
6150	""
6150	"""Go straight to Hector of the brazen helm,"
6150	Good Phoebus; for beneath the ocean wave
6150	Th' Earth-shaker hath withdrawn, escaping thus
6150	My high displeasure; had he dar'd resist,
6150	The tumult of our strife had reach'd the Gods
6150	Who in the nether realms with Saturn dwell.
6150	Yet thus 'tis better, both for me and him,
6150	That, though indignant, to my will he yields;
6150	For to compel him were no easy task.
6150	Take thou, and wave on high thy tassell'd shield,
6150	The Grecian warriors daunting: thou thyself,
6150	Far-darting King, thy special care bestow
6150	On noble Hector; so restore his strength
6150	And vigour, that in panic to their ships,
6150	And the broad Hellespont, the Greeks be driv'n.
6150	Then will I so by word and deed contrive
6150	"That they may gain fresh respite from their toil."""
6150	""
6150	He said, nor did Apollo not obey
6150	His Sire's commands; from Ida's heights he flew,
