6150	Like to a falcon, swooping on a dove,
6150	Swiftest of birds; then Priam's son he found,
6150	The godlike Hector, stretch'd at length no more,
6150	But sitting, now to consciousness restor'd,
6150	With recognition looking on his friends;
6150	The cold sweat dried, nor gasping now for breath,
6150	Since by the will of AEgis-bearing Jove
6150	To life new waken'd; close beside him stood
6150	The Far-destroyer, and address'd him thus:
6150	"""Hector, thou son of Priam, why apart"
6150	From all thy comrades art thou sitting here,
6150	"Feeble and faint? What trouble weighs thee down?"""
6150	""
6150	To whom thus Hector of the glancing helm
6150	"With falt'ring voice: ""Who art thou, Prince of Gods,"
6150	Who thus enquirest of me? know'st thou not
6150	How a huge stone, by mighty Ajax hurl'd,
6150	As on his comrades by the Grecian ships
6150	I dealt destruction, struck me on the breast,
6150	Dash'd to the earth, and all my vigour quell'd?
6150	I deem'd in sooth this day my soul, expir'd,
6150	"Should see the dead, and Pluto's shadowy realm."""
6150	""
6150	To whom again the far-destroying King:
6150	"""Be of good cheer; from Saturn's son I come"
6150	From Ida's height to be thy guide and guard;
6150	Phoebus Apollo, of the golden sword,
6150	I, who of old have thy protector been,
6150	Thee and thy city guarding. Rise then straight;
6150	Summon thy num'rous horsemen; bid them drive
6150	Their flying cars to assail the Grecian ships:
6150	I go before: and will thy horses' way
6150	"Make plain and smooth, and daunt the warrior Greeks."""
6150	""
6150	His words fresh vigour in the chief infus'd.
6150	As some proud steed, at well-fill'd manger fed,
6150	His halter broken, neighing, scours the plain,
6150	And revels in the widely-flowing stream
6150	To bathe his sides; then tossing high his head,
6150	While o'er his shoulders streams his ample mane,
6150	Light-borne on active limbs, in conscious pride,
6150	To the wide pastures of the mares he flies;
6150	So vig'rous, Hector plied his active limbs,
6150	His horsemen summoning at Heav'n's command.
6150	""
6150	As when a rustic crowd of men and dogs
6150	Have chas'd an antler'd stag, or mountain goat,
6150	That 'mid the crags and thick o'ershadowing wood
6150	Hath refuge found, and baffled their pursuit:
6150	If, by the tumult rous'd, a lion stand,
6150	With bristling mane, before them, back they turn,
6150	Check'd in their mid career; ev'n so the Greeks,
6150	Who late in eager throngs were pressing on,
6150	Thrusting with swords and double-pointed spears,
6150	When Hector moving through the ranks they saw,
6150	Recoil'd, and to their feet their courage fell.
6150	To whom thus Thoas spoke, Andraemon's son,
6150	AEtolia's bravest warrior, skill'd to throw
6150	The jav'lin, dauntless in the stubborn fight;
6150	By few surpass'd in speech, when in debate
6150	In full assembly Grecian youths contend.
6150	He thus with prudent speech began, and said:
6150	""
6150	"""Great is the marvel which our eyes behold,"
6150	That Hector see again to life restor'd,
6150	Escap'd the death we hop'd him to have met
6150	Beneath the hands of Ajax Telamon.
6150	Some God hath been his guard, and Hector sav'd,
6150	Whose arm hath slack'd the knees of many a Greek:
6150	So will he now; for not without the aid
6150	Of Jove, the Lord of thunder, doth he stand
6150	So boldly forth, so eager for the fight.
6150	Hear, then, and all by my advice be rul'd:
6150	Back to the ships dismiss the gen'ral crowd;
6150	While of our army we, the foremost men,
6150	Stand fast, and meeting him with levell'd spears,
6150	Hold him in check; and he, though brave, may fear
6150	"To throw himself amid our serried ranks."""
6150	""
6150	He said: they heard, and all obey'd his words:
6150	The mighty Ajax, and Idomeneus
6150	The King, and Teucer, and Meriones,
6150	And Meges, bold as Mars, with all their best,
6150	Their stedfast battle rang'd, to wait th' assault
6150	Of Hector and his Trojans; while behind,
6150	Th' unwarlike many to the ships retir'd.
6150	The Trojan mass came on, by Hector led
6150	With haughty stride; before him Phoebus went,
6150	His shoulders veil'd in cloud; his arm sustain'd
6150	The awful AEgis, dread to look on, hung
6150	With shaggy tassels round and dazzling bright;
6150	Which Vulcan, skilful workman, gave to Jove,
6150	To scatter terror 'mid the souls of men.
6150	This on his arm, the Trojan troops he led.
6150	Firm stood the mass of Greeks; from either side
6150	Shrill clamours rose; and fast from many a string
6150	The arrows flew, and many a jav'lin, hurl'd
6150	By vig'rous arms; some buried in the flesh
6150	Of stalwart youths, and many, ere they reach'd
6150	Their living mark, fell midway on the plain,
6150	Fix'd in the ground, in vain athirst for blood.
6150	While Phoebus motionless his AEgis held,
6150	Thick flew the shafts, and fast the people fell
6150	On either side; but when he turn'd its flash
6150	Full in the faces of the astonish'd Greeks,
6150	And shouted loud, their spirits within them quail'd,
6150	Their fiery courage borne in mind no more.
6150	As when two beasts of prey, at dead of night.
6150	With sudden onset scatter wide a herd
6150	Of oxen, or a num'rous flock of sheep,
6150	Their keepers absent; so unnerv'd by fear
6150	The Greeks dispers'd; such panic 'mid their ranks,
6150	That vict'ry so might crown the Trojan arms,
6150	Apollo sent; and as the masses broke,
6150	Each Trojan slew his man; by Hector's hand
6150	Fell Stichius and Arcesilas; the one,
6150	The leader of Boeotia's brass-clad host,
6150	The other, brave Menestheus' trusted friend.
6150	AEneas Medon slew, and Iasus;
6150	Medon, the great Oileus' bastard son,
6150	Brother of Ajax; he in Phylace,
6150	Far from his native home, was driv'n to dwell;
6150	Since one to Eriopis near akin,
6150	His sire Oileus' wife, his hand had slain:
6150	And Iasus, th' Athenian chief, was deem'd
6150	The son of Sphelus, son of Bucolus.
6150	Polydamas amid the foremost ranks
6150	Mecistes slew, Polites Echius,
6150	Agenor Olonius; while from Paris' hand
6150	An arrow, 'mid the crowd of fugitives
6150	Shot from behind, beneath the shoulder struck
6150	Deiocus, and through his chest was driv'n:
6150	These while the Trojans of their arms despoil'd,
6150	Through ditch and palisades promiscuous dash'd
6150	The flying Greeks, and gain'd, hard-press'd, the wall;
6150	While loudly Hector to the Trojans call'd
6150	To assail the ships, and leave the bloody spoils:
6150	"""Whom I elsewhere, and from the ships aloof"
6150	Shall find, my hand shall doom him on the spot;
6150	For him no fun'ral pyre his kin shall light,
6150	Or male or female; but before the wall
6150	"Our city's dogs his mangled flesh shall tear."""
6150	""
6150	He said; and on his horses' shoulder point
6150	Let fall the lash, and loudly through the ranks
6150	Call'd on the Trojans; they, with answ'ring shout
6150	And noise unspeakable, urg'd on with him
6150	Their harness'd steeds; Apollo, in the van,
6150	Trod down with ease th' embankment of the ditch,
6150	And fill'd it in; and o'er it bridg'd a way
6150	Level and wide, far as a jav'lin's flight
6150	Hurl'd by an arm that proves its utmost strength.
6150	O'er this their columns pass'd; Apollo bore
6150	His AEgis o'er them, and cast down the wall;
6150	Easy, as when a child upon the beach,
6150	In wanton play, with hands and feet o'erthrows
6150	The mound of sand, which late in play he rais'd;
6150	So, Phoebus, thou, the Grecian toil and pains
6150	Confounding, sentest panic through their souls.
6150	Thus hemm'd beside the ships they made their stand,
6150	While each exhorted each, and all, with hands
6150	Outstretch'd, to ev'ry God address'd their pray'r:
6150	And chief, Gerenian Nestor, prop of Greece,
6150	With hands uplifted tow'rd the starry Heav'n:
6150	""
6150	"""O Father Jove! if any e'er to Thee"
6150	On corn-clad plains of Argos burnt the fat
6150	Of bulls and sheep, and offer'd up his pray'r
6150	For safe return; and thine assenting nod
6150	Confirm'd thy promise; O remember now
6150	His pray'r; stave off the pitiless day of doom,
6150	"Nor let the Greeks to Trojan arms succumb."""
6150	""
6150	Thus Nestor pray'd; loud thunder'd from on high
6150	The Lord of counsel, as he heard the pray'r
6150	Of Neleus' aged son; with double zeal,
6150	The Trojans, as the mind of Jove they knew,
6150	Press'd on the Greeks, with warlike ardour fir'd.
6150	As o'er the bulwarks of a ship pour down
6150	The mighty billows of the wide-path'd sea,
6150	Driv'n by the blast, that tosses high the waves,
6150	So down the wall, with shouts, the Trojans pour'd;
6150	The cars admitted, by the ships they fought
6150	With double-pointed spears, and hand to hand;
6150	These on their chariots, on the lofty decks
6150	Of their dark vessels those, with pond'rous spars
6150	Which on the ships were stor'd for naval war,
6150	Compact and strong, their heads encas'd in brass.
6150	""
6150	While yet beyond the ships, about the wall
6150	The Greeks and Trojans fought, Patroclus still
6150	Within the tent of brave Eurypylus
6150	Remaining, with his converse sooth'd the chief,
6150	And healing unguents to his wound applied,
6150	Of pow'r to charm away the bitter pains;
6150	But when the Trojans pouring o'er the wall,
6150	And routed Greeks in panic flight he saw,
6150	Deeply he groan'd, and smiting on his thigh
6150	With either palm, in anguish thus he spoke:
6150	""
6150	"""Eurypylus, how great soe'er thy need,"
6150	I can no longer stay; so fierce the storm
6150	Of battle rages; but th' attendants' care
6150	Will all thy wants supply; while I in haste
6150	Achilles seek, and urge him to the war;
6150	Who knows but Heav'n may grant me to succeed?
6150	"For great is oft a friend's persuasive pow'r."""
6150	He said, and quickly on his errand sped.
6150	""
6150	Meanwhile the Greeks, in firm array, endur'd
6150	The onset of the Trojans; nor could these
6150	The assailants, though in numbers less, repel;
6150	Nor those again the Grecian masses break,
6150	And force their passage through the ships and tents,
6150	As by a rule, in cunning workman's hand,
6150	Who all his art by Pallas' aid has learnt,
6150	A vessel's plank is smooth and even laid,
6150	So level lay the balance of the fight.
6150	Others round other ships maintain'd the war,
6150	But Hector that of Ajax sought alone.
6150	For that one ship they two unwearied toil'd;
6150	Nor Hector Ajax from his post could move,
6150	And burn the ship with fire; nor he repel
6150	The foe who came protected by a God.
6150	Then noble Ajax with his jav'lin smote
6150	Caletor, son of Clytius, through the breast,
6150	As tow'rd the ship a blazing torch he bore;
6150	Thund'ring he fell, and dropp'd his hand the torch.
6150	But Hector, when his eyes his kinsman saw
6150	By the dark vessel, prostrate in the dust,
6150	On Trojans and on Lycians call'd aloud:
6150	""
6150	"""Trojans and Lycians, and ye Dardans, fam'd"
6150	In close encounter, in this press of war
6150	Slack not your efforts; haste to save the son
6150	Of Clytius, nor let Greeks his arms possess,
6150	"Who 'mid their throng of ships has nobly fall'n."""
6150	At Ajax, as he spoke, his gleaming spear
6150	He threw, but miss'd his aim; yet Lycophron,
6150	His comrade, of Cythera, Mastor's son
6150	(Who flying from Cythera's lovely isle
6150	With guilt of bloodshed, near to Ajax dwelt),
6150	Standing beside the chief, above the ear
6150	He struck, and pierc'd the brain: from the tall prow
6150	Backwards he fell, his limbs relax'd in death.
6150	Then Ajax, shudd'ring, on his brother call'd:
6150	""
6150	"""Good Teucer, we have lost a faithful friend,"
6150	The son of Mastor, our Cytheran guest,
6150	Whom as a father all rever'd; who now
6150	Lies slain by noble Hector. Where are then
6150	Thine arrows, swift-wing'd messengers of fate,
6150	"And where thy trusty bow, Apollo's gift?"""
6150	""
6150	Thus Ajax; Teucer heard, and ran in haste,
6150	And stood beside him, with his bended bow,
6150	And well-stor'd quiver: on the Trojans fast
6150	He pour'd his shafts; and struck Pisenor's son,
6150	Clitus, the comrade of Polydamas,
6150	The noble son of Panthous; he the reins
6150	Held in his hand, and all his care bestow'd
6150	To guide his horses; for, where'er the throng
6150	Was thickest, there in Hector's cause, and Troy's,
6150	He still was found; but o'er him hung the doom
6150	Which none might turn aside; for from behind
6150	The fateful arrow struck him through the neck;
6150	Down from the car he fell; swerving aside,
6150	The startled horses whirl'd the empty car.
6150	Them first the King Polydamas beheld,
6150	And stay'd their course; to Protiaon's son,
6150	Astynous, then he gave them, with command
6150	To keep good watch, and still be near at hand;
6150	Then 'mid the foremost join'd again the fray.
6150	Again at Hector of the brazen helm
6150	An arrow Teucer aim'd; and had the shaft
6150	The life of Hector quench'd in mid career,
6150	Not long the fight had rag'd around the ships:
6150	But Jove's all-seeing eye beheld, who watch'd
6150	O'er Hector's life, and Teucer's hopes deceiv'd.
6150	The bow's well-twisted string he snapp'd in twain,
6150	As Teucer drew; the brass-tipp'd arrow flew
6150	Wide of the mark, and dropp'd his hand the bow.
6150	Then to his brother, all aghast, he cried:
6150	"""O Heav'n, some God our best-laid schemes of war"
6150	Confounds, who from my hand hath, wrench'd the bow,
6150	And snapp'd the newly-twisted string, which I
6150	"But late attach'd, my swift-wing'd shafts to bear."""
6150	""
6150	Whom answer'd thus great Ajax Telamon:
6150	"""O friend, leave there thine arrows and thy bow,"
6150	Marr'd by some God who grudges our renown;
6150	But take in hand thy pond'rous spear, and cast
6150	Thy shield about thy shoulders, and thyself
6150	Stand forth, and urge the rest, to face the foe.
6150	Let us not tamely yield, if yield we must,
6150	"Our well-built ships, but nobly dare the fight."""
6150	""
6150	"""Thus Ajax spoke; and Teucer in the tent"
6150	Bestowed his bow, and o'er his shoulders threw
6150	His fourfold shield; and on his firm-set head
6150	A helm he plac'd, well-wrought, with horsehair plume,
6150	That nodded, fearful, o'er his brow; his hand
6150	Grasp'd the firm spear, with sharpen'd point of brass:
6150	Then ran, and swiftly stood by Ajax' side.
6150	Hector meanwhile, who saw the weapon marr'd,
6150	To Trojans and to Lycians call'd aloud:
6150	""
6150	"""Trojans and Lycians, and ye Dardans fam'd"
6150	In close encounter, quit ye now like men;
6150	Against the ships your wonted valour show.
6150	E'en now, before our eyes, hath Jove destroy'd
6150	A chieftain's weapon. Easy 'tis to trace
6150	O'er human wars th' o'erruling hand of Jove,
6150	To whom he gives the prize of victory,
6150	And whom, withholding aid, he minishes,
6150	As now the Greeks, while we his favour gain.
6150	Pour then your force united on the ships;
6150	And if there be among you, who this day
6150	Shall meet his doom, by sword or arrow slain,
6150	E'en let him die! a glorious death is his
6150	Who for his country falls; and dying, leaves
6150	Preserv'd from danger, children, wife, and home,
6150	His heritage uninjur'd, when the Greeks
6150	"Embarking hence shall take their homeward way."""
6150	""
6150	His words fresh courage rous'd in ev'ry breast.
6150	Ajax, on th' other side, address'd the Greeks:
6150	""
6150	"""Shame on ye, Greeks! this very hour decides"
6150	If we must perish, or be sav'd, and ward
6150	Destruction from our ships; and can ye hope
6150	That each, if Hector of the glancing helm
6150	Shall burn our ships, on foot can reach his home?
6150	Or hear ye not, how, burning to destroy
6150	Our vessels, Hector cheers his forces on?
6150	Not to the dance, but to the fight he calls;
6150	Nor better counsel can for us be found,
6150	Than in close fight with heart and hand to join.
6150	'Twere better far at once to die, than live
6150	Hemm'd in and straiten'd thus, in dire distress,
6150	"Close to our ships, by meaner men beset."""
6150	""
6150	His words fresh courage rous'd in ev'ry breast.
6150	Then Hector Schedius, Perimedes' son,
6150	The Thracian leader, slew; on th' other side
6150	Ajax the captain of the foot o'ercame,
6150	Laodamas, Antenor's noble son;
6150	While of his arms Polydamas despoil'd
6150	Cyllenian Otus, friend of Phyleus' son,
6150	The proud Epeians' leader; Meges saw,
6150	And rush'd upon him; but Polydamas,
6150	Stooping, the blow evaded; him he miss'd;
6150	For Phoebus will'd not Panthous' son should fall
6150	In the front rank contending; but the spear
6150	Smote Croesmus through the breast; thund'ring he fell,
6150	And from his corpse the victor stripp'd his arms.
6150	Him Dolops, son of Lampus, spearman skill'd,
6150	Well train'd in ev'ry point of war, assail'd
6150	(The son of Lampus he, the prince of men,
6150	Son of Laomedon); from close at hand
6150	Forward he sprang, and thrust at Meges' shield;
6150	But him the solid corslet which he wore,
6150	With breast and back-piece fitted, sav'd from harm:*
6150	The corslet Phyleus brought from Ephyra,
6150	By Selles' stream; Euphetes, King of men,
6150	Bestow'd it as a friendly gift, to wear
6150	In battle for a guard from hostile spears;
6150	Which from destruction now preserv'd his son.
6150	Next Meges struck, with keen-edg'd spear, the crown
6150	Of Dolops' brass-bound, horsehair-crested helm,
6150	Sev'ring the horsehair plume, which, brilliant late
6150	With crimson dye, now lay defil'd in dust.
6150	Yet fought he on, and still for vict'ry hop'd;
6150	But warlike Menelaus to the aid
6150	Of Meges came; of Dolops unobserv'd
6150	He stood, and from behind his shoulder pierc'd;
6150	The point, its course pursuing, through his breast
6150	Was driv'n, and headlong on his face he fell.
6150	Forthwith, advanc'd the two to seize the spoils;
6150	But loudly Hector on his kinsmen call'd;
6150	On all, but chief on Icetaon's son,
6150	The valiant Melanippus; he erewhile,
6150	In far Percote, ere the foes appear'd,
6150	Pastur'd his herds; but when the ships of Greece
6150	Approach'd the shore, to Ilium back he came;
6150	There, 'mid the Trojans eminent, he dwelt
6150	In Priam's house, belov'd as Priam's son.
6150	Him Hector call'd by name, and thus address'd:
6150	""
6150	"""Why, Melanippus, stand we idly thus?"
6150	Doth not thy slaughter'd kinsmen touch thy heart?
6150	See how they rush on Dolops' arms to seize;
6150	Then on! no distant war must now be wag'd,
6150	But hand to hand, till or the Greeks be slain,
6150	"Or lofty Troy, with all her children, fall."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and led the way; him follow'd straight
6150	The godlike chief; great Ajax Telamon
6150	Meanwhile the Greeks encourag'd to the fight,
6150	"And cried, ""Brave comrades, quit ye now like men;"
6150	Bear a stout heart; and in the stubborn fight
6150	Let each to other mutual succour give;
6150	By mutual succour more are sav'd than fall;
6150	"In timid flight nor fame nor safety lies."""
6150	""
6150	He said; and pond'ring well his words, they stood,
6150	Firm in defence; as with a wall of brass
6150	The ships they guarded; though against them Jove
6150	Led on the Trojans; Menelaus then
6150	With stirring words Antilochus address'd:
6150	"""Antilochus, than thou, of all the Greeks"
6150	Is none more active, or more light of foot;
6150	None stronger hurls the spear; then from the crowd
6150	"Spring forth, and aim to reach some Trojan's life."""
6150	""
6150	Thus saying, he withdrew; fir'd by his words,
6150	Forth sprang the youth, and pois'd his glitt'ring spear,
6150	Glancing around him; back the Trojans drew
6150	Before his aim; nor flew the spear in vain;
6150	But through the breast it pierc'd, as on he came,
6150	Brave Melanippus, Icetaon's son.
6150	Thund'ring he fell, and loud his armour rang.
6150	Forth sprang Antilochus, as springs a hound
6150	Upon a fawn, which from its lair disturb'd
6150	A hunter's shaft has struck, and quell'd its pow'rs;
6150	So, Melanippus, sprang to seize thy spoils
6150	The stout Antilochus; but not unmark'd
6150	Of Hector's eye, who, hast'ning through the press,
6150	Advanc'd to meet him; waited not th' attack,
6150	Bold warrior as he was, Antilochus,
6150	But trembling fled: as when a beast of prey,
6150	Conscious of evil deed, amid the herd
6150	The guardian dog or herdsman's self has slain,
6150	And flies, ere yet th' avenging crowd collect;
6150	So fled the son of Nestor; onward press'd,
6150	By Hector led, the Trojans; loud their shouts,
6150	As on the Greeks their murd'rous shafts they pour'd:
6150	Yet turn'd he, when his comrades' ranks he reach'd.
6150	Then on the ships, as rav'ning lions, fell
6150	The Trojans: they but work'd the will of Jove,
6150	Who still their courage rais'd, and quell'd the Greeks;
6150	Of vict'ry these debarr'd, and those inspir'd;
6150	For so he will'd, that Hector, Priam's son,
6150	Should wrap in fire the beaked ships of Greece,
6150	And Thetis to the uttermost obtain
6150	Her over-bold petition; yet did Jove,
6150	The Lord of counsel, wait but to behold
6150	The flames ascending from the blazing ships:
6150	For from that hour the Trojans, backward driv'n,
6150	Should to the Greeks the final triumph leave.
6150	With such design, to seize the ships, he fir'd
6150	Th' already burning zeal of Priam's son;
6150	Fiercely he rag'd, as terrible as Mars
6150	With brandish'd spear; or as a raging fire
6150	'Mid the dense thickets on the mountain side.
6150	The foam was on his lips; bright flash'd his eyes
6150	Beneath his awful brows, and terribly
6150	Above his temples wav'd amid the fray
6150	The helm of Hector; Jove himself from Heav'n.
6150	His guardian hand extending, him alone
6150	With glory crowning 'mid the host of men;
6150	But short his term of glory: for the day
6150	Was fast approaching, when, with Pallas' aid,
6150	The might of Peleus' son should work his doom.
6150	Oft he essay'd to break the ranks, where'er
6150	The densest and throng noblest arms he saw;
6150	But strenuous though his efforts, all were vain:
6150	They, mass'd in close array, his charge withstood;
6150	Firm as a craggy rock, upstanding high,
6150	Close by the hoary sea, which meets unmov'd
6150	The boist'rous currents of the whistling winds,
6150	And the big waves that bellow round its base;
6150	So stood unmov'd the Greeks, and undismay'd.
6150	At length, all blazing in his arms, he sprang
6150	Upon the mass; so plunging down, as when
6150	On some tall vessel, from beneath the clouds
6150	A giant billow, tempest-nurs'd, descends;
6150	The deck is drench'd in foam; the stormy wind
6150	Howls in the shrouds; th' affrighted seamen quail
6150	In fear, but little way from death remov'd;
6150	So quail'd the spirit in ev'ry Grecian breast.
6150	""
6150	As when a rav'ning lion on a herd
6150	Of heifers falls, which on some marshy mead
6150	Feed numberless, beneath the care of one,
6150	Unskill'd from beasts of prey to guard his charge;
6150	And while beside the front or rear he walks,
6150	The lion on th' unguarded centre springs,
6150	Seizes on one, and scatters all the rest;
6150	So Hector, led by Jove, in wild alarm
6150	Scatter'd the Grecians all; but one alone,
6150	Brave Periphetes, of Mycenae, slew;
6150	The son of Copreus, whom Eurystheus sent
6150	His envoy to the might of Hercules;
6150	Far nobler than the father was the son;
6150	In speed of foot, in warlike might, in mind,
6150	In all, among Mycenians foremost he;
6150	Who now on Hector fresh renown conferr'd;
6150	For, backward as he stepp'd, against the rim
6150	Of the broad shield which for defence he bore,
6150	Down reaching to his feet, he tripp'd, and thus
6150	Entangled, backward fell; and as he fell,
6150	Around his temples clatter'd loud his helm.
6150	Hector beheld, and o'er him stood in haste,
6150	And with his spear transfix'd his breast, and slew
6150	Before his comrades' eyes; yet dar'd not one,
6150	Though grieving for their comrade's loss, advance
6150	To rescue; such of Hector was their awe.
6150	They fronted now the ships; the leading prows
6150	Which first were drawn on shore, still barr'd their way;
6150	Yet on they stream'd; and from the foremost ships,
6150	Now hardly press'd, the Greeks perforce retir'd;
6150	But closely mass'd before the tents they stood,
6150	Not scatter'd o'er the camp; by shame restrain'd,
6150	And fear; and loudly each exhorted each.
6150	Gerenian Nestor chief, the prop of Greece,
6150	Thus by their fathers singly each adjur'd:
6150	"""Quit ye like men, dear friends; and think it shame"
6150	To forfeit now the praise of other men;
6150	Let each man now his children and his wife,
6150	His fortunes and his parents, bear in mind;
6150	And not the living only, but the dead;
6150	For them, the absent, I, your suppliant, pray,
6150	"That firm ye stand, and scorn disgraceful flight."""
6150	""
6150	His words fresh courage rous'd in ev'ry breast;
6150	And from their eyeballs Pallas purg'd away
6150	The film of darkness; and on ev'ry side,
6150	Both tow'rd the ships and tow'rd the level fight,
6150	Clear light diffus'd; there Hector they discern'd,
6150	And all his comrades, those who stood aloof,
6150	And those who near the ships maintain'd the war.
6150	Then was not Ajax' mighty soul content
6150	To stand where stood the other sons of Greece;
6150	Along the vessels' lofty decks he mov'd
6150	With haughty stride; a pond'rous boarding-pike,
6150	Well polish'd, and with rivets well secur'd,
6150	Of two and twenty cubits' length, he bore,
6150	As one well-skill'd in feats of horsemanship,
6150	Who from a troop of horses on the plain
6150	Has parted four, and down the crowded road,
6150	While men and women all in wonder gaze,
6150	Drives tow'rd the city; and with force untir'd
6150	From one to other springs, as on they fly;
6150	O'er many a vessel's deck so Ajax pass'd
6150	With lofty stride, and voice that reach'd to Heav'n,
6150	As loudly shouting on the Greeks he call'd
6150	To save their ships and tents: nor Hector stay'd
6150	Amid the closely buckler'd Trojan ranks;
6150	But, as upon a flock of birds, that feed
6150	Beside a river's bank, or geese, or cranes,
6150	Or long-neck'd swans, a fiery eagle swoops;
6150	So on the dark-prow'd ship with furious rush
6150	Swept Hector down; him Jove with mighty hand
6150	Sustain'd, and with him forward urg'd the crowd.
6150	Fierce round the ships again the battle rag'd;
6150	Well might ye deem no previous toil had worn
6150	Their strength, who in that dread encounter met;
6150	With edge so keen, and stubborn will they fought.
6150	But varying far their hopes and fears: the Greeks
6150	Of safety and escape from death despair'd;
6150	While high the hopes in ev'ry Trojan's breast,
6150	To burn the ships, and slay the warlike Greeks;
6150	So minded each, oppos'd in arms they stood.
6150	""
6150	On a swift-sailing vessel's stern, that bore
6150	Protesilaus to the coast of Troy,
6150	But to his native country bore not thence,
6150	Hector had laid his hand; around that ship
6150	Trojans and Greeks in mutual slaughter join'd.
6150	The arrow's or the jav'lin's distant flight
6150	They waited not, but, fir'd with equal rage,
6150	Fought hand to hand, with axe and hatchet keen,
6150	And mighty swords, and double-pointed spears.
6150	Many a fair-hilted blade, with iron bound,
6150	Dropp'd from the hands, or from the sever'd arms,
6150	Of warrior chiefs; the dark earth ran with blood:
6150	Yet loos'd not Hector of the stern his hold,
6150	But grasp'd the poop, and on the Trojans call'd;
6150	""
6150	"""Bring fire, and all together loud and clear"
6150	Your war-cry raise; this day will Jove repay
6150	Our labours all, with capture of those ships,
6150	Which hither came, against the will of Heav'n,
6150	And which on us unnumber'd ills have brought,
6150	By our own Elders' fault, who me, desiring
6150	Ev'n at their vessels' sterns to urge the war,
6150	Withheld, and to the town the troops confin'd.
6150	But Jove all-seeing, if he then o'errul'd
6150	"Our better mind, himself is now our aid."""
6150	""
6150	Thus he: they onward press'd with added zeal;
6150	Nor Ajax yet endur'd, by hostile spears
6150	Now sorely gall'd; yet but a little space,
6150	Back to the helmsman's sev'n-foot board he mov'd,
6150	Expecting death; and left the lofty deck,
6150	Where long he stood on guard; but still his spear
6150	The Trojans kept aloof, whoe'er essay'd
6150	Amid the ships to launch th' unwearied flames;
6150	And, loudly shouting, to the Greeks he call'd:
6150	""
6150	"""Friends, Grecian heroes, ministers of Mars,"
6150	Quit ye like men! dear friends, remember now
6150	Your wonted valour! think ye in your rear
6150	To find supporting forces, or some fort
6150	Whose walls may give you refuge from your foe?
6150	No city is nigh, whose well-appointed tow'rs,
6150	Mann'd by a friendly race, may give us aid;
6150	But here, upon the well-arm'd Trojans' soil,
6150	And only resting on the sea, we lie
6150	Far from our country; not in faint retreat,
6150	"But in our own good arms, our safety lies."""
6150	""
6150	He said; and with his sharp-edg'd spear his words
6150	He follow'd up; if any Trojan dar'd,
6150	By Hector's call inspir'd, with fiery brand
6150	To assail the ships, him with his ponderous spear
6150	Would Ajax meet; and thus before the ships
6150	Twelve warriors, hand to hand, his prowess felt.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	ARGUMENT.
6150	""
6150	THE SIXTH BATTLE; THE ACTS AND DEATH OF PATROCLUS.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	Patroclus (in pursuance of the request of Nestor in the eleventh book)
6150	entreats Achilles to suffer him to go to the assistance of the Greeks
6150	with Achilles' troops and armour. He agrees to it, but at the same time
6150	charges him to content himself with rescuing the fleet, without farther
6150	pursuit of the enemy. The armour, horses, soldiers, and officers of
6150	Achilles are described. Achilles offers a libation for the success of
6150	his friend, after which Patroclus leads the Myrmidons to battle. The
6150	Trojans, at the sight of Patroclus in Achilles' armour, taking him for
6150	that hero, are cast into the utmost consternation: he beats them off
6150	from the vessels, Hector himself flies, Sarpedon is killed, though
6150	Jupiter was averse to his fate. Several other particulars of the battle
6150	are described; in the heat of which, Patroclus, neglecting the orders
6150	of Achilles, pursues the foe to the walls of Troy; where Apollo
6150	repulses and disarms him, Euphorbus wounds him, and Hector kills him:
6150	which concludes the book.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	BOOK XVI.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	Thus round the well-mann'd ship they wag'd the war:
6150	Meanwhile by Peleus' son Patroclus stood,
6150	Weeping hot tears; as some dark-water'd fount
6150	Pours o'er a craggy rock its gloomy stream;
6150	Achilles, swift of foot, with pity saw,
6150	And to his friend these winged words address'd:
6150	""
6150	"""Why weeps Patroclus, like an infant girl,"
6150	That prays her mother, by whose side she runs,
6150	To take her up; and, clinging to her gown,
6150	Impedes her way, and still with tearful eyes
6150	Looks in her face, until she take her up?
6150	Ev'n as that girl, Patroclus, such art thou,
6150	Shedding soft tears: hast thou some tidings brought
6150	Touching the gen'ral weal, or me alone?
6150	Or have some evil news from Phthia come,
6150	Known but to thee? Menoetius, Actor's son,
6150	Yet surely lives; and 'mid his Myrmidons
6150	Lives aged Peleus, son of AEacus:
6150	Their deaths indeed might well demand our tears:
6150	Or weep'st thou for the Greeks, who round their ships
6150	By death their former insolence repay?
6150	"Speak out, that I may know thy cause of grief."""
6150	""
6150	To whom, with bitter groans, Patroclus thus:
6150	"""O son of Peleus, noblest of the Greeks,"
6150	Achilles, be not wroth! such weight of woe
6150	The Grecian camp oppresses; in their ships
6150	They who were late their bravest and their best,
6150	Sore wounded all by spear or arrow lie;
6150	The valiant son of Tydeus, Diomed,
6150	Pierc'd by a shaft, Ulysses by a spear,
6150	And Agamemnon's self; Eurypylus
6150	By a sharp arrow through the thigh transfix'd;
6150	For these, the large resources of their art
6150	The leeches ply, and on their wounds attend;
6150	While thou, Achilles, still remain'st unmov'd.
6150	Oh, be it never mine to nurse such hate
6150	As thou retain'st, inflexibly severe!
6150	Who e'er may hope in future days by thee
6150	To profit, if thou now forbear to save
6150	The Greeks from shame and loss? Unfeeling man!
6150	Sure Peleus, horseman brave, was ne'er thy sire,
6150	Nor Thetis bore thee; from the cold grey sea
6150	And craggy rocks thou hadst thy birth; so hard
6150	And stubborn is thy soul. But if the fear
6150	Of evil prophesied thyself restrain,
6150	Or message by thy Goddess-mother brought
6150	From Jove, yet send me forth with all thy force
6150	Of Myrmidons, to be the saving light
6150	Of Greece; and let me to the battle bear
6150	Thy glitt'ring arms, if so the men of Troy,
6150	Scar'd by thy likeness, may forsake the field,
6150	And breathing-time afford the sons of Greece,
6150	Toil-worn; for little pause has yet been theirs.
6150	Fresh and unwearied, we may drive with ease
6150	To their own city, from our ships and tents,
6150	"The Trojans, worn and battle-wearied men."""
6150	""
6150	Thus pray'd he, all unwisely; for the pray'r
6150	He utter'd, to himself was fraught with death;
6150	To whom, much griev'd, Achilles, swift of foot:
6150	"""Heav'n-born Patroclus, oh, what words are these!"
6150	Of prophecy I reck not, though I know;
6150	Nor message hath my mother brought from Jove;
6150	But it afflicts my soul; when one I see
6150	That basely robs his equal of his prize,
6150	His lawful prize, by highest valour won;
6150	Such grief is mine, such wrong have I sustain'd.
6150	Her, whom the sons of Greece on me bestow'd,
6150	Prize of my spear, the well-wall'd city storm'd,
6150	The mighty Agamemnon, Atreus' son,
6150	Hath borne by force away, as from the hands
6150	Of some dishonour'd, houseless vagabond.
6150	But let the past be past; I never meant
6150	My wrath should have no end; yet had not thought
6150	My anger to abate, till my own ships
6150	Should hear the war-cry, and the battle bear,
6150	But go, and in my well-known armour clad,
6150	Lead forth the valiant Myrmidons to war,
6150	Since the dark cloud of Trojans circles round
6150	The ships in force; and on the shingly beach,
6150	Pent up in narrow limits, lie the Greeks;
6150	And all the city hath pour'd its numbers forth
6150	In hope undoubting; for they see no more
6150	My helm among them flashing; else in flight
6150	Their dead would choke the streams, if but to me
6150	Great Agamemnon bore a kindly mind:
6150	But round the camp the battle now is wag'd.
6150	No more the hands of valiant Diomed,
6150	The Greeks protecting, hurl his fiery spear;
6150	Nor hear I now, from his detested lips,
6150	The shout of Agamemnon; all around
6150	Is heard the warrior-slayer Hector's voice,
6150	Cheering his Trojans; with triumphant cries
6150	They, from the vanquish'd Greeks, hold all the plain.
6150	Nathless do thou, Patroclus, in defence
6150	Fall boldly on, lest they with blazing fire
6150	Our ships destroy, and hinder our retreat.
6150	But hear, and ponder well the end of all
6150	I have to say, and so for me obtain
6150	Honour and glory in the eyes of Greece;
6150	And that the beauteous maiden to my arms
6150	They may restore, with costly gifts to boot.
6150	The ships reliev'd, return forthwith; and though
6150	The Thund'rer, Juno's Lord, should crown thine arms
6150	With triumph, be not rash, apart from me,
6150	In combat with the warlike sons of Troy;
6150	 (So should my name in less repute be held;)
6150	Nor, in the keen excitement of the fight
6150	And slaughter of the Trojans, lead thy troops
6150	On tow'rd the city, lest thou find thyself
6150	By some one of th' immortal Gods oppos'd;
6150	For the far-darting Phoebus loves them well;
6150	But when in safety thou hast plac'd the ships,
6150	Delay not to return, and leave the rest
6150	To battle on the plain: for would to Jove,
6150	To Pallas and Apollo, that not one,
6150	Or Greek or Trojan, might escape from death,
6150	Save only thou and I; that so we two
6150	"Alone might raze the sacred tow'rs of Troy."""
6150	""
6150	Such converse held they; while by hostile spears
6150	Hard press'd, no longer Ajax might endure;
6150	At once by Jove's high will and Trojan foes
6150	O'ermaster'd; loud beneath repeated blows
6150	Clatter'd around his brow the glitt'ring helm,
6150	As on the well-wrought crest the weapons fell;
6150	And his left arm grew faint, that long had borne
6150	The burthen of his shield; yet nought avail'd
6150	The press of spears to drive him from his post;
6150	Lab'ring he drew his breath, his ev'ry limb
6150	With sweat was reeking; breathing space was none;
6150	Blow follow'd blow; and ills were heap'd on ill.
6150	""
6150	Say now, ye Nine, who on Olympus dwell,
6150	How first the fire assail'd the Grecian ships.
6150	""
6150	Hector approach'd, and on the ashen spear
6150	Of Ajax, close behind the head, let fall
6150	His mighty sword; right through he clove the wood;
6150	And in his hand the son of Telamon
6150	The headless shaft held bootless; far away,
6150	Loud ringing, fell to earth the brazen point.
6150	Ajax, dismayed, perceived the hand of Heaven,
6150	And knew that Jove the Thunderer had decreed
6150	To thwart his hopes, and victory give to Troy.
6150	Slow he retir'd; and to the vessel they
6150	The blazing torch applied; high rose the flame
6150	Unquenchable, and wrapp'd the poop in fire.
6150	The son of Peleus saw, and with his palm
6150	Smote on his thigh, and to Patroclus call'd:
6150	"""Up, nobly born Patroclus, car-borne chief!"
6150	Up, for I see above the ships ascend
6150	The hostile fires; and lest they seize the ships,
6150	And hinder our retreat, do thou in haste
6150	"Thine armour don, while I arouse the troops."""
6150	""
6150	He said: his dazzling arms Patroclus donn'd:
6150	First on his legs the well-wrought greaves he fix'd,
6150	Fasten'd with silver clasps; his ample chest
6150	The breastplate of Achilles, swift of foot,
6150	Star-spangled, richly wrought, defended well;
6150	Around his shoulders slung, his sword he bore,
6150	Brass-bladed, silver-studded; next his shield
6150	Weighty and strong; and on his firm-set head
6150	A helm he wore, well-wrought, with horsehair plume
6150	That nodded, fearful, o'er his brow; his hand
6150	Grasp'd two stout spears, familiar to his hold.
6150	One spear Achilles had, long, pond'rous, tough;
6150	But this he touch'd not; none of all the Greeks,
6150	None, save Achilles' self, that spear could poise;
6150	The far-fam'd Pelian ash, which to his sire,
6150	On Pelion's summit fell'd, to be the bane
6150	Of mightiest chiefs, the Centaur Chiron gave.
6150	Then to Automedon he gave command
6150	To yoke the horses: him he honour'd most,
6150	Next to Achilles' self; the trustiest he
6150	In battle to await his chief's behest.
6150	The flying steeds he harness'd to the car,
6150	Xanthus and Balius, fleeter than the winds;
6150	Whom, grazing in the marsh by ocean's stream,
6150	Podarge, swift of foot, to Zephyr bore:
6150	And by their side the matchless Pedasus,
6150	Whom from the capture of Eetion's town
6150	Achilles bore away; a mortal horse,
6150	But with immortal coursers meet to vie.
6150	""
6150	Meantime Achilles, through their several tents,
6150	Summon'd to arms the warlike Myrmidons.
6150	They all, like rav'ning wolves, of courage high,
6150	That on the mountain side have hunted down
6150	An antler'd stag, and batten'd on his flesh:
6150	Their chaps all dyed with blood, in troops they go,
6150	With their lean tongues from some black-water'd fount
6150	To lap the surface of the dark cool wave,
6150	Their jaws with blood yet reeking, unsubdued
6150	Their courage, and their bellies gorg'd with flesh;
6150	So round Pelides' valiant follower throng'd
6150	The chiefs and rulers of the Myrmidons.
6150	Achilles in the midst to charioteers
6150	And buckler'd warriors issued his commands.
6150	Fifty swift ships Achilles, dear to Jove,
6150	Led to the coast of Troy; and rang'd in each
6150	Fifty brave comrades mann'd the rowers' seats.
6150	O'er these five chiefs, on whom he most relied,
6150	He plac'd, himself the Sov'reign Lord of all.
6150	One band Menestheus led, with glancing mail,
6150	Son of Sperchius, Heav'n-descended stream;
6150	Him Peleus' daughter, Polydora fair,
6150	A mortal in a God's embrace compress'd,
6150	To stout Sperchius bore; but, by repute,
6150	To Boras, Perieres' son, who her
6150	In public, and with ample dow'r, espous'd.
6150	The brave Eudorus led the second band,
6150	Whom Phylas' daughter, Polymele fair,
6150	To Hermes bore; the maid he saw, and lov'd,
6150	Amid the virgins, mingling in the dance
6150	Of golden-shafted Dian, Huntress-Queen;
6150	He to her chamber access found, and gain'd
6150	By stealth her bed; a valiant son she bore,
6150	Eudorus, swift of foot, in battle strong.
6150	But when her infant, by Lucina's aid,
6150	Was brought to light, and saw the face of day,
6150	Her to his home, with ample dow'r enrich'd,
6150	Echecles, son of Actor, bore away;
6150	While him the aged Phylas kept, and nurs'd
6150	With tender care, and cherish'd--as his own.
6150	The brave Peisander, son of Maemalus,
6150	The third commanded; of the Myrmidons,
6150	Next to Pelides' friend, the noblest spear.
6150	The fourth, the aged warrior Phoenix led;
6150	The fifth, Alcimedon, Laerces' son:
6150	These in their order due Achilles first
6150	Array'd, and next with stirring words address'd:
6150	""
6150	"""Ye Myrmidons, forget not now the vaunts"
6150	Which, while my wrath endur'd, ye largely pour'd
6150	Upon the Trojans; me ye freely blam'd;
6150	'Ill-omen'd son of Peleus, sure in wrath
6150	Thou wast conceiv'd, implacable, who here
6150	In idleness enforc'd thy comrades keep'st!
6150	'Twere better far our homeward way to take,
6150	If such pernicious rancour fill thy soul!'
6150	Thus ye reproach'd me oft! Lo! now ye have
6150	The great occasion which your souls desir'd!
6150	"Then on, and with brave hearts the Trojans meet!"""
6150	""
6150	His words fresh courage rous'd in ev'ry breast,
6150	And more compact, beneath their monarch's eye,
6150	Their ranks were form'd; as when the builder lays
6150	The closely-fitting stones, to form the wall
6150	Of some great house, and brave the winds of Heav'n;
6150	So close were fitted helm and bossy shield;
6150	Buckler on buckler press'd, and helm on helm,
6150	And man on man; the horsehair plumes above,
6150	That nodded, fearful, from the warriors' brows,
6150	Each other touch'd; so closely mass'd they stood.
6150	Before them all stood prominent in arms
6150	Two chiefs, Patroclus and Automedon,
6150	Both with one thought possess'd, to lead the fight
6150	In the fore-front of all the Myrmidons.
6150	Achilles then within his tent withdrew,
6150	And of a gorgeous coffer rais'd the lid,
6150	Well-wrought, by silver-footed Thetis plac'd
6150	On board his ship, and fill'd with rich attire,
6150	With store of wind-proof cloaks, and carpets soft.
6150	There lay a goblet, richly chas'd, whence none,
6150	But he alone, might drink the ruddy wine,
6150	Nor might libations thence to other Gods
6150	Be made, save only Jove: this brought he forth,
6150	And first with sulphur purified, and next
6150	Wash'd with pure water; then his hands he wash'd,
6150	And drew the ruddy wine; then standing forth
6150	Made in the centre of the court his pray'r,
6150	And as he pour'd the wine, look'd up to Heav'n,
6150	Not unbeheld of Jove, the lightning's Lord:
6150	""
6150	"""Great King, Dodona's Lord, Pelasgian Jove,"
6150	Who dwell'st on high, and rul'st with sov'reign sway
6150	Dodona's wintry heights; where dwell around
6150	Thy Sellian priests, men of unwashen feet,
6150	That on the bare ground sleep; thou once before
6150	Hast heard my pray'r, and me with honour crown'd,
6150	And on the Greeks inflicted all thy plagues;
6150	Hear yet again, and this my boon accord.
6150	I 'mid the throng of ships myself remain;
6150	But with a num'rous force of Myrmidons
6150	I send my comrade in my stead to fight:
6150	On him, all-seeing Jove, thy favour pour;
6150	Strengthen his heart, that Hector's self may learn
6150	If, e'en alone, my follower knows to fight,
6150	Or only then resistless pow'r displays,
6150	When I myself the toil of battle share.
6150	And from our vessels when the foe is driv'n,
6150	Grant that with all his arms and comrades true
6150	"He may in safety to the ships return."""
6150	""
6150	Thus pray'd he; Jove, the Lord of counsel, heard,
6150	And half his pray'r he granted, half denied:
6150	For from the ships the battle to repel
6150	He granted; but denied his safe return.
6150	His pray'rs and off'rings ended, to the tent
6150	Achilles turn'd again, and in the chest
6150	Replac'd the cup; then issuing forth, he stood
6150	Before the tent; for much he long'd to see
6150	The Greeks and Trojans join in battle strife.
6150	They who in arms round brave Patroclus stood
6150	Their line of battle form'd, with courage high
6150	To dash upon the Trojans; and as wasps
6150	That have their nest beside the public road,
6150	Which boys delight to vex and irritate
6150	In wanton play, but to the gen'ral harm;
6150	Them if some passing trav'ller unawares
6150	Disturb, with angry courage forth they rush
6150	In one continuous swarm, to guard their nest:
6150	E'en with such courage pour'd the Myrmidons
6150	Forth from the ships; then uproar wild arose,
6150	And loud Patroclus on his comrades call'd:
6150	""
6150	"""Ye valiant Myrmidons, who boast yourselves"
6150	Achilles' comrades, quit ye now like men;
6150	Your ancient valour prove; to Peleus' son,
6150	Of all the Greeks the noblest, so shall we,
6150	His faithful followers, highest honour give;
6150	And Agamemnon's haughty self shall mourn
6150	"The slight on Grecia's bravest warrior cast."""
6150	""
6150	His words fresh courage rous'd in ev'ry breast.
6150	Thick on the Trojan host their masses fell;
6150	While loud the fleet re-echoed to the sound
6150	Of Grecian cheers; but when the Trojans saw,
6150	Blazing in arms, Menoetius' godlike son,
6150	Himself, and follower; quail'd the spirits of all;
6150	Their firm-set ranks were shaken; for they deem'd
6150	Achilles had beside the ships exchang'd
6150	His wrath for friendship; and each sev'ral man
6150	Look'd round, to find his own escape from death.
6150	""
6150	Then first Patroclus aim'd his glitt'ring spear
6150	Amid the crowd, where thickest round the ships
6150	Of brave Protesilaus, raged the war;
6150	And struck Pyraechmes, who from Amydon,
6150	From the wide-flowing stream of Axius, led
6150	The horsehair-crested Paeons; him he struck
6150	Through the right shoulder; backwards in the dust
6150	Groaning, he fell; around him quail'd with fear
6150	His Paeons all, such terror in their ranks
6150	Patroclus threw, their bravest leader slain,
6150	The foremost in the fight; the crowd he drove
6150	Far from the ships, and quench'd the blazing fire.
6150	There lay the half-burnt ship; with shouts confus'd
6150	The Trojans fled; and from amid the ships
6150	Forth pour'd the Greeks; and loud the clamour rose.
6150	""
6150	As when around a lofty mountain's top
6150	The lightning's Lord dispels a mass of cloud,
6150	And ev'ry crag, and ev'ry jutting peak
6150	Is plainly seen, and ev'ry forest glade;
6150	And the deep vault of Heav'n is open'd wide;
6150	So when the Greeks had clear'd the ships of fire,
6150	They breath'd awhile; yet ceas'd not so the strife;
6150	For not in headlong panic from the ships
6150	The Trojans by the valiant Greeks were driv'n,
6150	But, though perforce retiring, still made head.
6150	""
6150	Then of the chiefs, as wider spread the fight,
6150	Each singled each; Menoetius' noble son
6150	First threw his pointed spear, and on the thigh
6150	Struck Areilochus, in act to turn;
6150	Right through the point was driv'n; the weighty spear
6150	Shatter'd the bone, and prone to earth he fell.
6150	The warlike Menelaus aim'd his spear
6150	Where Thoas' breast, unguarded by his shield,
6150	Was left expos'd; and slack'd his limbs in death.
6150	Phyleus' brave son, as rush'd Amphiclus on,
6150	Stood firm, with eye observant; then th' attack
6150	Preventing, through his thigh, high up, where lie
6150	The strongest muscles, smote; the weapon's point
6150	Sever'd the tendons; darkness clos'd his eyes.
6150	Of Nestor's sons, Antilochus, the first,
6150	Atymnius wounded, driving through his flank
6150	He brazen spear; prone on his face he fell.
6150	Then, burning to avenge his brother's death,
6150	Stood Maris o'er the corpse, and hand to hand
6150	Engaged Antilochus; but ere a blow
6150	Was struck, the godlike Thrasymedes drove
6150	Through his right shoulder, with unerring aim,
6150	His glitt'ring spear; the point his upper arm
6150	Tore from the muscles, shatt'ring all the bone:
6150	Thund'ring he fell, and darkness clos'd his eyes.
6150	So to the shades, by those two brethren's hands
6150	Subdued, Sarpedon's comrades brave were sent,
6150	The sons of Amisodarus, who rear'd
6150	The dread Chimaera, bane of mortal men.
6150	On Cleobulus, wounded in the press,
6150	Ajax Oileus sprang, and captive took,
6150	Alive; but sudden on his neck let fall
6150	His hilted sword, and quench'd the fire of life.
6150	The hot blood dyed the sword; the darkling shades
6150	Of death, and rig'rous fate, his eyes o'erspread.
6150	Then Peneleus and Lycon, hand to hand,
6150	Engag'd in combat; both had miss'd their aim,
6150	And bootless hurl'd their weapons; then with swords
6150	They met; first Lycon on the crested helm
6150	Dealt a fierce blow; but in his hand the blade
6150	Up to the hilt was shiver'd; then the sword
6150	Of Peneleus his neck, below the ear,
6150	Dissever'd; deeply in his throat the blade
6150	Was plung'd, and by the skin alone was stay'd;
6150	Down droop'd his head, his limbs relax'd in death.
6150	Meriones by speed of foot o'ertook,
6150	And, as his car he mounted, Acarnas
6150	Though the right shoulder pierc'd; down from the car
6150	He fell; the shades of death his eyes o'erspread.
6150	Full on the mouth of Erymas was thrust
6150	The weapon of Idomeneus; right through,
6150	The white bones crashing, pass'd the brazen spear
6150	Below the brain; his teeth were shatter'd all;
6150	With blood, which with convulsive sobs he blew
6150	From mouth and nostril, both his eyes were fill'd;
6150	And death's dark cloud encompass'd him around.
6150	Thus slew the Grecian leaders each his man.
6150	""
6150	As rav'ning wolves, that lambs or kids assail,
6150	Stray'd from their dams, by careless shepherds left
6150	Upon the mountain scatter'd; these they see,
6150	And tear at once their unresisting prey;
6150	So on the Trojans fell the Greeks; in rout
6150	Disastrous they, unmann'd by terror, fled.
6150	Great Ajax still, unwearied, long'd to hurl
6150	His spear at Hector of the brazen helm;
6150	But he, well skill'd in war, his shoulders broad
6150	Protected by his shield of tough bull's hide,
6150	Watch'd for the whizzing shafts, and jav'lins' whirr.
6150	Full well he knew the tide of battle turn'd,
6150	Yet held his ground, his trusty friends to save.
6150	""
6150	As from Olympus, o'er the clear blue sky
6150	Pour the dark clouds, when Jove the vault of Heav'n
6150	O'erspreads with storm and tempest, from the ships
6150	So pour'd with panic cries the flying host,
6150	And in disorder'd rout recross'd the trench.
6150	Then Hector's flying coursers bore him safe
6150	Far from the struggling masses, whom the ditch
6150	Detain'd perforce; there many a royal car
6150	With broken pole th' unharness'd horses left.
6150	On, shouting to the Greeks, Patroclus press'd
6150	The flying Trojans; they, with panic cries,
6150	Dispers'd, the roads encumber'd; high uprose
6150	The storms of dust, as from the tents and ships
6150	Back to the city stretch'd the flying steeds;
6150	And ever where the densest throng appear'd
6150	With furious threats Patroclus urg'd his course;
6150	His glowing axle trac'd by prostrate men
6150	Hurl'd from their cars, and chariots overthrown.
6150	Flew o'er the deep-sunk trench th' immortal steeds,
6150	The noble prize the Gods to Peleus gave,
6150	Still onward straining; for he long'd to reach,
6150	And hurl his spear at Hector; him meanwhile
6150	His flying steeds in safety bore away.
6150	""
6150	As in th' autumnal season, when the earth
6150	With weight of rain is saturate; when Jove
6150	Pours down his fiercest storms in wrath to men,
6150	Who in their courts unrighteous judgments pass,
6150	And justice yield to lawless violence,
6150	The wrath of Heav'n despising; ev'ry stream
6150	Is brimming o'er: the hills in gullies deep
6150	Are by the torrents seam'd, which, rushing down
6150	From the high mountains to the dark-blue sea,
6150	With groans and tumult urge their headlong course,
6150	Wasting the works of man; so urg'd their flight,
6150	So, as they fled, the Trojan horses groan'd.
6150	The foremost ranks cut off, back tow'rd the ships
6150	Patroclus drove them, baffling their attempts
6150	To gain the city; and in middle space
6150	Between the ships, the stream, and lofty wall,
6150	Dealt slaughter round him, and of many a chief
6150	The bitter penalty of death requir'd.
6150	Then Pronous with his glitt'ring spear he struck,
6150	Where by the shield his breast was left expos'd,
6150	And slack'd his limbs in death; thund'ring he fell.
6150	Next Thestor, son of OEnops, he assail'd;
6150	He on his polish'd car, down-crouching, sat,
6150	His mind by fear disorder'd; from his hands
6150	The reins had dropp'd; him, thrusting with the spear,
6150	Through the right cheek and through the teeth he smote,
6150	Then dragg'd him, by the weapon, o'er the rail.
6150	As when an angler on a prominent rock
6150	Drags from the sea to shore with hook and line
6150	A weighty fish; so him Patroclus dragg'd,
6150	Gaping, from off the car; and dash'd him down
6150	Upon his face; and life forsook his limbs.
6150	Next Eryalus, eager for the fray,
6150	On the mid forehead with a mighty stone
6150	He struck; beneath the pond'rous helmet's weight
6150	The skull was split in twain; prostrate he fell,
6150	By life-consuming death encompass'd round.
6150	Forthwith Amphoterus, and Erymas,
6150	Epaltes, Echius, and Tlepolemus,
6150	Son of Damastor, Pyris, Ipheus brave,
6150	Euippus, Polymelus, Argeas' son,
6150	In quick succession to the ground he brought.
6150	Sarpedon his ungirdled forces saw
6150	Promiscuous fall before Menoetius' son,
6150	And to the Lycians call'd in loud reproof:
6150	"""Shame, Lycians! whither fly ye? why this haste?"
6150	I will myself this chief confront, and learn
6150	Who this may be of bearing proud and high,
6150	Who on the Trojans grievous harm hath wrought,
6150	"And many a warrior's limbs relax'd in death."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and from his car, accoutred, sprang;
6150	Patroclus saw, and he too leap'd to earth.
6150	As on a lofty rock, with angry screams,
6150	Hook-beak'd, with talons curv'd, two vultures fight;
6150	So with loud shouts these two to battle rush'd.
6150	The son of Saturn pitying saw, and thus
6150	To Juno spoke, his sister and his wife:
6150	""
6150	"""Woe, woe! that fate decrees my best-belov'd,"
6150	Sarpedon, by Patroclus' hand to fall;
6150	E'en now conflicting thoughts my soul divide,
6150	To bear him from the fatal strife unhurt,
6150	And set him down on Lycia's fertile plains,
6150	"Or leave him by Patroclus' hand to fall."""
6150	""
6150	Whom, answer'd thus the stag-ey'd Queen of Heav'n:
6150	"""What words, dread son of Saturn, dost thou speak?"
6150	Wouldst thou a mortal man from death withdraw
6150	Long since by fate decreed? Do what thou wilt;
6150	Yet cannot we, the rest, applaud thine act.
6150	This too I say, and turn it in thy mind:
6150	If to his home Sarpedon thou restore
6150	Alive, bethink thee, will not other Gods
6150	Their sons too from the stubborn fight withdraw?
6150	For in the field around the walls of Troy
6150	Are many sons of Gods, in all of whom
6150	This act of thine will angry feelings rouse.
6150	But if thou love him, and thy soul deplore
6150	His coming doom, yet in the stubborn fight
6150	Leave him beneath Patroclus' hand to fall:
6150	Then, when his spirit hath fled, the charge assign
6150	To Death and gentle Sleep, that in their arms
6150	They bear him safe to Lycia's wide-spread plains:
6150	There shall his brethren and his friends perform
6150	His fun'ral rites, and mound and column raise,
6150	"The fitting tribute to the mighty dead."""
6150	""
6150	Thus she; the Sire of Gods and men complied:
6150	But to the ground some drops of blood let fall,
6150	In honour of his son, whom fate decreed,
6150	Far from his country, on the fertile plains
6150	Of Troy to perish by Patroclus' hand.
6150	As near the champions drew, Patroclus first
6150	His weapon hurl'd, and Thrasymedes brave,
6150	The faithful follower of Sarpedon, struck
6150	Below the waist, and slack'd his limbs in death.
6150	Thrown in his turn, Sarpedon's glitt'ring spear
6150	Flew wide; and Pedasus, the gallant horse,
6150	Through the right shoulder wounded; with a scream
6150	He fell, and in the dust breath'd forth his life,
6150	As, shrieking loud, his noble spirit fled.
6150	This way and that his two companions swerv'd;
6150	Creak'd the strong yoke, and tangled were the reins,
6150	As in the dust the prostrate courser lay.
6150	Automedon the means of safety saw;
6150	And drawing from beside his brawny thigh
6150	His keen-edg'd sword, with no uncertain blow
6150	Cut loose the fallen horse; again set straight,
6150	The two, extended, stretch'd the tightened rein.
6150	Again in mortal strife the warriors clos'd:
6150	Once more Sarpedon hurl'd his glitt'ring spear
6150	In vain; above Patroclus' shoulder flew
6150	The point, innocuous; from his hand in turn
6150	The spear not vainly thrown, Sarpedon struck
6150	Where lies the diaphragm, below the heart.
6150	He fell; as falls an oak, or poplar tall,
6150	Or lofty pine, which on the mountain top
6150	For some proud ship the woodman's axe hath hewn:
6150	So he, with death-cry sharp, before his car
6150	Extended lay, and clutch'd the blood-stain'd soil.
6150	As when a lion on the herd has sprung,
6150	And, 'mid the heifers seiz'd, the lordly bull
6150	Lies bellowing, crush'd between the lion's jaws;
6150	So by Patroclus slain, the Lycian chief,
6150	Undaunted still, his faithful comrade call'd:
6150	"""Good Glaucus, warrior tried, behoves thee now"
6150	Thy spearmanship to prove, and warlike might.
6150	Welcome the fray; put forth thine utmost speed;
6150	Call on the Lycian chiefs, on ev'ry side,
6150	To press around, and for Sarpedon fight;
6150	Thou too thine arms for my protection wield;
6150	For I to thee, through all thy future days,
6150	Shall be a ceaseless scandal and reproach,
6150	If me, thus slain before the Grecian ships,
6150	The Greeks be suffer'd of my arms to spoil:
6150	"But stand thou fast, and others' courage raise."""
6150	""
6150	Thus as he spoke, the shades of death o'erspread
6150	His eyes and nostrils; then with foot firm-set
6150	Upon his chest, Patroclus from the corpse
6150	Drew, by main force, the fast-adhering spear;
6150	The life forth issuing with the weapon's point.
6150	Loos'd from the royal car, the snorting steeds,
6150	Eager for flight, the Myrmidons detain'd.
6150	Deep-grieving, Glaucus heard his voice: and chafed
6150	His spirit within him, that he lacked the power
6150	To aid his comrade; with his hand he grasp'd
6150	His wounded arm, in torture from the shaft
6150	By Teucer shot, to save the Greeks from death,
6150	As on he pressed to scale the lofty wall:
6150	Then to Apollo thus address'd his pray'r:
6150	""
6150	"""Hear me, great King, who, as on Lycia's plains,"
6150	Art here in Troy; and hear'st in ev'ry place
6150	Their voice who suffer, as I suffer now.
6150	A grievous wound I bear, and sharpest pangs
6150	My arm assail, nor may the blood he stanch'd:
6150	The pain weighs down my shoulder; and my hand
6150	Hath lost its pow'r to fight, or grasp my spear.
6150	Sarpedon, bravest of the brave, is slain,
6150	The son of Jove; yet Jove preserv'd him not.
6150	But thou, O King, this grievous wound relieve;
6150	Assuage the pain, and give me strength to urge
6150	My Lycian comrades to maintain the war,
6150	"And fight myself to guard the noble dead."""
6150	""
6150	Thus as he pray'd, his pray'r Apollo heard,
6150	Assuag'd his pains, and from the grievous wound
6150	Stanch' d the dark blood, and fill'd his soul with strength.
6150	Glaucus within himself perceiv'd, and knew,
6150	Rejoicing, that the God had heard his pray'r.
6150	The Lycian leaders first on ev'ry side
6150	He urg'd to hasten for their King to fight:
6150	Then 'mid the Trojans went with lofty step,
6150	And first to Panthous' son, Polydamas,
6150	To brave Agenor and AEneas next;
6150	Then Hector of the brazen helm himself
6150	Approaching, thus with winged words address'd:
6150	""
6150	"""Hector, forgett'st thou quite thy brave allies,"
6150	Who freely in thy cause pour forth their lives,
6150	Far from their home and friends? but they from thee
6150	No aid receive; Sarpedon lies in death,
6150	The leader of the buckler'd Lycian bands,
6150	Whose justice and whose pow'r were Lycia's shield;
6150	Him by Patroclus' hand hath Mars subdued.
6150	But, friends, stand by me now! with just revenge
6150	Inspir'd, determine that the Myrmidons
6150	Shall not, how griev'd soe'er for all the Greeks
6150	Who by our spears beside the ships have fall'n,
6150	"Our dead dishonour, and his arms obtain."""
6150	""
6150	He said; and through the Trojans thrill'd the sense
6150	Of grief intolerable, unrestrain'd;
6150	For he, though stranger-born, was of the State
6150	A mighty pillar; and his followers
6150	A num'rous host; and he himself in fight
6150	Among the foremost; so, against the Greeks,
6150	With fiery zeal they rush'd, by Hector led,
6150	Griev'd for Sarpedon's loss; on th' other side
6150	Patroclus' manly heart the Greeks arous'd,
6150	And to th' Ajaces first, themselves inflamed
6150	With warlike zeal, he thus address'd his speech:
6150	""
6150	"""Ye sons of Ajax, now is come the time"
6150	Your former fame to rival, or surpass:
6150	The man hath fall'n, who first o'erleap'd our wall,
6150	Sarpedon; now remains, that, having slain,
6150	We should his corpse dishonour, and his arms
6150	Strip off; and should some comrade dare attempt
6150	"His rescue, him too with our spears subdue."""
6150	""
6150	He said; and they, with martial ardour fir'd,
6150	Rush'd to the conflict. When on either side
6150	The reinforc'd battalions were array'd,
6150	Trojans and Lycians, Myrmidons and Greeks
6150	Around the dead in sternest combat met,
6150	With fearful shouts; and loud their armour rang.
6150	Then, to enhance the horror of the strife
6150	Around his son, with darkness Jove o'erspread
6150	The stubborn fight: the Trojans first drove back
6150	The keen-ey'd Greeks; for first a warrior fell,
6150	Not of the meanest 'mid the Myrmidons,
6150	Epegeus, son of valiant Agacles;
6150	Who in Budaeum's thriving state bore rule
6150	Erewhile; but flying for a kinsman slain,
6150	To Peleus and the silver-footed Queen
6150	He came a suppliant; with Achilles thence
6150	To Ilium sent, to join the war of Troy.
6150	Him, as he stretch'd his hand to seize the dead,
6150	Full on the forehead with a massive stone
6150	Great Hector smote; within the pond'rous helm
6150	The skull was split in twain; prone on the corpse
6150	He fell, by life-destroying death subdued.
6150	Griev'd was Patroclus for his comrade slain;
6150	Forward he darted, as a swift-wing'd hawk,
6150	That swoops amid the starlings and the daws;
6150	So swift didst thou, Patroclus, car-borne chief,
6150	Upon the Trojans and the Lycians spring,
6150	Thy soul with anger for thy comrade fill'd.
6150	A pond'rous stone he hurl'd at Sthenelas,
6150	Son of Ithaemenes; the mighty mass
6150	Fell on his neck, and all the muscles crush'd.
6150	Back drew great Hector and the chiefs of Troy;
6150	Far as a jav'lin's flight, in sportive strife,
6150	Or in the deadly battle, hurl'd by one
6150	His utmost strength exerting; back so far
6150	The Trojans drew, so far the Greeks pursued.
6150	Glaucus, the leader of the Lycian spears,
6150	First turning, slew the mighty Bathycles,
6150	The son of Chalcon; he in Hellas dwelt,
6150	In wealth surpassing all the Myrmidons.
6150	Him, as he gain'd upon him in pursuit,
6150	Quick turning, Glaucus through the breast transfix'd;
6150	Thund'ring he fell; deep grief possess'd the Greeks
6150	At loss of one so valiant; fiercely joy'd
6150	The Trojans, and around him crowded thick;
6150	Nor of their wonted valour were the Greeks
6150	Oblivious, but still onward held their course.
6150	Then slew Meriones a crested chief,
6150	The bold Laogonus, Onetor's son;
6150	Onetor, of Idaean Jove the priest,
6150	And by the people as a God rever'd.
6150	Below the ear he struck him; from his limbs
6150	The spirit fled, and darkness veil'd his eyes.
6150	""
6150	Then at Meriones AEneas threw
6150	His brazen spear, in hopes beneath his shield
6150	To find a spot unguarded; he beheld,
6150	And downward stooping, shunn'd the brazen death;
6150	Behind him far, deep in the soil infix'd,
6150	The weapon stood; there Mars its impulse stay'd;
6150	So, bootless hurl'd, though by no feeble hand,
6150	AEneas' spear stood quiv'ring in the ground;
6150	"Then thus in wrath he cried: ""Meriones,"
6150	Had it but struck thee, nimble as thou art,
6150	"My spear had brought thy dancing to a close."""
6150	""
6150	To whom the spearman skill'd, Meriones:
6150	"""Brave as thou art, AEneas, 'tis too much"
6150	For thee to hope the might of all to quell,
6150	Who dare confront thee; thou art mortal too!
6150	And if my aim be true, and should my spear
6150	But strike thee fair, all valiant as thou art,
6150	And confident, yet me thy fall shall crown
6150	"With triumph, and thy soul to Hades send."""
6150	""
6150	He said; and him Menoetius' noble son
6150	"Address'd with grave rebuke: ""Meriones,"
6150	Brave warrior, why thus waste the time in words?
6150	Trust me, good friend, 'tis not by vaunting speech,
6150	Unseconded by deeds, that we may hope
6150	To scare away the Trojans from the slain:
6150	Hands are for battle, words for council meet;
6150	"Boots it not now to wrangle, but to fight."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and led the way; him follow'd straight
6150	The godlike chief; forthwith, as loudly rings,
6150	Amid the mountain forest's deep recess,
6150	The woodman's axe, and far is heard the sound;
6150	So from the wide-spread earth their clamour rose,
6150	As brazen arms, and shields, and tough bull's-hide
6150	Encounter'd swords and double-pointed spears.
6150	Nor might the sharpest sight Sarpedon know,
6150	From head to foot with wounds and blood and dust
6150	Disfigur'd; thickly round the dead they swarm'd.
6150	As when at spring-tide in the cattle-sheds
6150	Around the milk-cans swarm the buzzing flies,
6150	While the warm milk is frothing in the pail;
6150	So swarm'd they round the dead; nor Jove the while
6150	Turn'd from the stubborn fight his piercing glance;
6150	But still look'd down with gaze intent, and mus'd
6150	Upon Patroclus' coming fate, in doubt,
6150	If he too there beside Sarpedon slain,
6150	Should perish by illustrious Hector's hand,
6150	Spoil'd of his arms; or yet be spared awhile
6150	To swell the labours of the battle-field.
6150	He judg'd it best at length, that once again
6150	The gallant follower of Peleus' son
6150	Should tow'rd the town with fearful slaughter drive
6150	The Trojans, and their brazen-helmed chief.
6150	First Hector's soul with panic fear he fill'd;
6150	Mounting his car, he fled, and urg'd to flight
6150	The Trojans; for he saw the scales of Jove.
6150	Then nor the valiant Lycians held their ground;
6150	All fled in terror, as they saw their King
6150	Pierc'd through the heart, amid a pile of dead;
6150	For o'er his body many a warrior fell,
6150	When Saturn's son the conflict fierce inflam'd.
6150	Then from Sarpedon's breast they stripp'd his arms,
6150	Of brass refulgent; these Menoetius' son
6150	Sent by his comrades to the ships of Greece.
6150	""
6150	To Phoebus then the Cloud-compeller thus:
6150	"""Hie thee, good Phoebus, from amid the spears"
6150	Withdraw Sarpedon, and from all his wounds
6150	Cleanse the dark gore; then bear him far away,
6150	And lave his body in the flowing stream;
6150	Then with divine ambrosia all his limbs
6150	Anointing, clothe him in immortal robes.
6150	To two swift bearers give him then in charge,
6150	To Sleep and Death, twin brothers, in their arms
6150	To bear him safe to Lycia's wide-spread plains:
6150	There shall his brethren and his friends perform
6150	His fun'ral rites, and mound and column raise,
6150	"The fitting tribute to the mighty dead."""
6150	""
6150	He said; obedient to his father's words,
6150	Down to the battle-field Apollo sped
6150	From Ida's height; and from amid the spears
6150	Withdrawn, he bore Sarpedon far away,
6150	And lav'd his body in the flowing stream;
6150	Then with divine ambrosia all his limbs
6150	Anointing, cloth'd him in immortal robes;
6150	To two swift bearers gave him then in charge,
6150	To Sleep and Death, twin brothers; in their arms
6150	They bore him safe to Lycia's wide-spread plains.
6150	""
6150	Then to Automedon Patroclus gave
6150	His orders, and the flying foe pursued.
6150	Oh much deceiv'd, insensate! had he now
6150	But borne in mind the words of Peleus' son,
6150	He might have 'scap'd the bitter doom of death.
6150	But still Jove's will the will of man o'errules:
6150	Who strikes with panic, and of vict'ry robs
6150	The bravest; and anon excites to war;
6150	Who now Patroclus' breast with fury fill'd.
6150	Whom then, Patroclus, first, whom slew'st thou last,
6150	When summon'd by the Gods to meet thy doom?
6150	Adrastus, and Autonous, Perimus
6150	The son of Meges, and Echeclus next;
6150	Epistor, Melanippus, Elasus,
6150	And Mulius, and Pylartes; these he slew;
6150	The others all in flight their safety found.
6150	""
6150	Then had the Greeks the lofty-gated town
6150	Of Priam captur'd by Patroclus' hand,
6150	So forward and so fierce he bore his spear;
6150	But on the well-built tow'r Apollo stood,
6150	On his destruction bent, and Troy's defence
6150	The jutting angle of the lofty wall
6150	Patroclus thrice assail'd; his onset thrice
6150	Apollo, with his own immortal hands
6150	Repelling, backward thrust his glitt'ring shield.
6150	But when again, with more than mortal force
6150	He made his fourth attempt, with awful mien
6150	And threat'ning voice the Far-destroyer spoke:
6150	""
6150	"""Back, Heav'n-born chief, Patroclus! not to thee"
6150	Hath fate decreed the triumph to destroy
6150	The warlike Trojans' city; no, nor yet
6150	"To great Achilles, mightier far than thou."""
6150	""
6150	Thus as he spoke, Patroclus backward stepp'd,
6150	Shrinking before the Far-destroyer's wrath.
6150	Still Hector kept before the Scaean gates
6150	His coursers; doubtful, if again to dare
6150	The battle-throng, or summon all the host
6150	To seek the friendly shelter of the wall.
6150	Thus as he mus'd, beside him Phoebus stood,
6150	In likeness of a warrior stout and brave,
6150	Brother of Hecuba, the uncle thence
6150	Of noble Hector, Asius, Dymas' son;
6150	Who dwelt in Phrygia, by Saugarius' stream;
6150	His form assuming, thus Apollo spoke:
6150	"""Hector, why shrink'st thou from the battle thus?"
6150	It ill beseems thee! Would to Heav'n that I
6150	So far thy greater were, as thou art mine;
6150	Then sorely shouldst thou rue this abstinence.
6150	But, forward thou! against Patroclus urge
6150	Thy fiery steeds, so haply by his death
6150	"Apollo thee with endless fame may crown."""
6150	""
6150	This said, the God rejoin'd the strife of men;
6150	And noble Hector bade Cebriones
6150	Drive 'mid the fight his car; before him mov'd
6150	Apollo, scatt'ring terror 'mid the Greeks,
6150	And lustre adding to the arms of Troy.
6150	All others Hector pass'd unnotic'd by,
6150	Nor stay'd to slay; Patroclus was the mark
6150	At which his coursers' clatt'ring hoofs he drove.
6150	On th' other side, Patroclus from his car
6150	Leap'd to the ground: his left hand held his spear;
6150	And in the right a pond'rous mass he bore
6150	Of rugged stone, that fill'd his ample grasp:
6150	The stone he hurl'd; not far it miss'd its mark,
6150	Nor bootless flew; but Hector's charioteer
6150	It struck, Cebriones, a bastard son
6150	Of royal Priam, as the reins he held.
6150	Full on his temples fell the jagged mass,
6150	Drove both his eyebrows in, and crush'd the bone;
6150	Before him in the dust his eyeballs fell;
6150	And, like a diver, from the well-wrought car
6150	Headlong he plung'd; and life forsook his limbs.
6150	O'er whom Patroclus thus with bitter jest:
6150	"""Heav'n! what agility! how deftly thrown"
6150	That somersault! if only in the sea
6150	Such feats he wrought, with him might few compete,
6150	Diving for oysters, if with such a plunge
6150	He left his boat, how rough soe'er the waves,
6150	As from his car he plunges to the ground:
6150	"Troy can, it seems, accomplish'd tumblers boast."""
6150	""
6150	Thus saying, on Cebriones he sprang,
6150	As springs a lion, through the breast transfix'd,
6150	In act the sheepfold to despoil, and dies
6150	The victim of his courage; so didst thou
6150	Upon Cebriones, Patroclus, spring.
6150	Down from his car too Hector leap'd to earth.
6150	So, o'er Cebriones, oppos'd they stood;
6150	As on the mountain, o'er a slaughter'd stag,
6150	Both hunger-pinch'd, two lions fiercely fight,
6150	So o'er Cebriones two mighty chiefs,
6150	Menoetius' son and noble Hector, strove,
6150	Each in the other bent to plunge his spear.
6150	The head, with grasp unyielding, Hector held;
6150	Patroclus seiz'd the foot; and, crowding round,
6150	Trojans and Greeks in stubborn conflict clos'd.
6150	As when, encount'ring in some mountain-glen,
6150	Eurus and Notus shake the forest deep,
6150	Of oak, or ash, or slender cornel-tree,
6150	Whose tap'ring branches are together thrown,
6150	With fearful din, and crash of broke a boughs;
6150	So mix'd confus'dly, Greeks and Trojans fought,
6150	No thought of flight by either entertain'd.
6150	Thick o'er Cebriones the jav'lins flew,
6150	And feather'd arrows, bounding from the string;
6150	And pond'rous stones that on the bucklers rang,
6150	As round the dead they fought; amid the dust
6150	That eddying rose, his art forgotten all,
6150	A mighty warrior, mightily he lay.
6150	While in mid Heav'n the sun pursued his course,
6150	Thick flew the shafts, and fast the people fell
6150	On either side; but when declining day
6150	Brought on the hour that sees the loosen'd steers,
6150	The Greeks were stronger far; and from the darts
6150	And Trojan battle-cry Cebriones
6150	They drew, and from his breast his armour stripp'd.
6150	Fiercely Patroclus on the Trojans fell:
6150	Thrice he assail'd them, terrible as Mars,
6150	With fearful shouts; and thrice nine foes he slew:
6150	But when again, with more than mortal force
6150	His fourth assault he made, thy term of life,
6150	Patroclus, then approach'd its final close;
6150	For Phoebus' awful self encounter'd thee,
6150	Amid the battle-throng, of thee unseen,
6150	For thickest darkness shrouded all his form:
6150	He stood behind, and with extended palm
6150	Dealt on Patroclus' neck and shoulders broad
6150	A mighty buffet; dizzy swam his eyes,
6150	And from his head Apollo snatch'd the helm;
6150	Clank'd, as it roll'd beneath the horses' feet,
6150	The visor'd helm; the horsehair plume with blood
6150	And dust polluted; never till that day
6150	Was that proud helmet so with dust defil'd,
6150	That wont to deck a godlike chief, and guard
6150	Achilles' noble head, and graceful brow:
6150	Now by the will of Jove to Hector giv'n.
6150	Now death was near at hand; and in his grasp
6150	His spear was shiver'd, pond'rous, long, and tough,
6150	Brass-pointed; with its belt, the ample shield
6150	Fell from his shoulders; and Apollo's hand,
6150	The royal son of Jove, his corslet loos'd.
6150	Then was his mind bewilder'd; and his limbs
6150	Gave way beneath him; all aghast he stood:
6150	Him, from behind, a Dardan, Panthous' son,
6150	Euphorbus, peerless 'mid the Trojan youth,
6150	To hurl the spear, to run, to drive the car,
6150	Approaching close, between the shoulders stabb'd;
6150	He, train'd to warfare, from his car, ere this
6150	A score of Greeks had from their chariots hurl'd:
6150	Such was the man who thee, Patroclus, first
6150	Wounded, but not subdued; the ashen spear
6150	He, in all haste, withdrew; nor dar'd confront
6150	Patroclus, though disarm'd, in deadly strife.
6150	""
6150	Back to his comrades' shelt'ring ranks retir'd,
6150	From certain death, Patroclus: by the stroke
6150	Of Phoebus vanquish'd, and Euphorbus' spear:
6150	But Hector, when Patroclus from the fight
6150	He saw retreating, wounded, through the ranks
6150	Advancing, smote him through the flank; right through
6150	The brazen spear was driv'n; thund'ring he fell;
6150	And deeply mourn'd his fall the Grecian host.
6150	""
6150	As when a lion hath in fight o'erborne
6150	A tusked boar, when on the mountain top
6150	They two have met, in all their pride of strength,
6150	Both parch'd with thirst, around a scanty spring;
6150	And vanquish'd by the lion's force, the boar
6150	Hath yielded, gasping; so Menoetius' son,
6150	Great deeds achiev'd, at length beneath the spear
6150	Of noble Hector yielded up his life;
6150	Who o'er the vanquish'd, thus exulting, spoke:
6150	"""Patroclus, but of late thou mad'st thy boast"
6150	To raze our city walls, and in your ships
6150	To bear away to your far-distant land,
6150	Their days of freedom lost, our Trojan dames:
6150	Fool that thou wast! nor knew'st, in their defence,
6150	That Hector's flying coursers scour'd the plain;
6150	From them, the bravest of the Trojans, I
6150	Avert the day of doom; while on our shores
6150	Thy flesh shall glut the carrion birds of Troy.
6150	Poor wretch! though brave he be, yet Peleus' son
6150	Avail'd thee nought, when, hanging back himself,
6150	With sage advice he sent thee forth to fight:
6150	'Come not to me, Patroclus, car-borne chief,
6150	Nor to the ships return, until thou bear
6150	The warrior-slayer Hector's bloody spoils,
6150	Torn from his body;' such were, I suppose,
6150	"His counsels; thou, poor fool, becam'st his dupe."""
6150	To whom Patroclus thus in accents faint:
6150	""
6150	"""Hector, thou boastest loudly now, that Jove,"
6150	With Phoebus join'd, hath thee with vict'ry crown'd:
6150	They wrought my death, who stripp'd me of my arms.
6150	Had I to deal with twenty such as thee,
6150	They all should perish, vanquish'd by my spear:
6150	Me fate hath slain, and Phoebus; and, of men,
6150	Euphorbus; thou wast but the third to strike.
6150	This too I say, and bear it in thy mind;
6150	Not long shalt thou survive me; death e'en now
6150	And final doom hangs o'er thee, by the hand
6150	"Of great Achilles, Peleus' matchless son."""
6150	""
6150	Thus as he spoke, the gloom of death his eyes
6150	O'erspread, and to the shades his spirit fled,
6150	Mourning his fate, his youth and strength cut off.
6150	To whom, though dead, the noble Hector thus:
6150	"""Patroclus, why predict my coming fate?"
6150	Or who can say but fair-hair'd Thetis' son,
6150	"Achilles, by my spear may first be slain?"""
6150	""
6150	He said, and planting firm his foot, withdrew
6150	The brazen spear, and backward drove the dead
6150	From off the weapon's point; then, spear in hand,
6150	Intent to slay, Automedon pursued,
6150	The godlike follower of AEacides:
