6150	But him in safety bore th' immortal steeds,
6150	The noble prize the Gods to Peleus gave.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	ARGUMENT.
6150	""
6150	THE SEVENTH BATTLE, FOR THE BODY OF PATROCLUS.--THE ACTS OF MENELAUS.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	Menelaus, upon the death of Patroclus, defends his body from the enemy;
6150	Euphorbus, who attempts it, is slain. Hector advancing, Menelaus
6150	retires; but soon returns with Ajax, and drives him off. This Glaucus
6150	objects to Hector as a flight, who thereupon puts on the armour he had
6150	won from Patroclus, and renews the battle. The Greeks give way, till
6150	Ajax rallies them: AEneas sustains the Trojans. AEneas and Hector
6150	attempt the chariot of Achilles, which is borne off by Automedon. The
6150	horses of Achilles deplore the loss of Patroclus; Jupiter covers his
6150	body with a thick darkness; the noble prayer of Ajax on that occasion.
6150	Menelaus sends Antilochus to Achilles, with the news of Patroclus's
6150	death: then returns to the fight, where, though attacked with the
6150	utmost fury, he and Meriones, assisted by the Ajaces, bear off the body
6150	to the ships.
6150	""
6150	The time is the evening of the eight-and-twentieth day. The scene lies
6150	in the fields before Troy.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	BOOK XVII.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	Nor was Patroclus' fall, by Trojans slain,
6150	Of warlike Menelaus unobserv'd;
6150	Forward he sprang, in dazzling arms array'd,
6150	And round him mov'd, as round her new-dropp'd calf
6150	Her first, a heifer moves with plaintive moan:
6150	So round Patroclus Menelaus mov'd,
6150	His shield's broad orb and spear before him held,
6150	To all who might oppose him threat'ning death.
6150	Nor, on his side, was Panthous' noble son
6150	Unmindful of the slain; but, standing near,
6150	The warlike Menelaus thus address'd:
6150	""
6150	"""Illustrious son of Atreus, Heav'n-born chief,"
6150	Quit thou the dead; yield up the bloody spoils:
6150	For, of the Trojans and their fam'd Allies,
6150	Mine was the hand that in the stubborn fight
6150	First struck Patroclus; leave me then to wear
6150	Among the men of Troy my honours due,
6150	"Lest by my spear thou lose thy cherish'd life."""
6150	""
6150	To whom in anger Menelaus thus:
6150	"""O Father Jove, how ill this vaunting tone"
6150	Beseems this braggart! In their own esteem,
6150	"""With Panthous' sons for courage none may vie;"
6150	Nor pard, nor lion, nor the forest boar,
6150	Fiercest of beasts, and proudest of his strength.
6150	Yet nought avail'd to Hyperenor's might
6150	His youthful vigour, when he held me cheap,
6150	And my encounter dar'd; of all the Greeks
6150	He deem'd my prowess least; yet he, I ween,
6150	On his own feet return'd not, to rejoice
6150	His tender wife's and honour'd parents' sight.
6150	So shall thy pride be quell'd, if me thou dare
6150	Encounter; but I warn thee, while 'tis time,
6150	Ere ill betide thee, 'mid the gen'ral throng
6150	That thou withdraw, nor stand to me oppos'd.
6150	"After th' event may e'en a fool be wise."""
6150	He spoke in vain; Euphorbus thus replied:
6150	""
6150	"""Now, Heav'n-born Menelaus, shalt thou pay"
6150	The forfeit for my brother's life, o'er whom,
6150	Slain by thy hand, thou mak'st thy boasting speech.
6150	Thou in the chambers of her new-found home
6150	Hast made his bride a weeping widow; thou
6150	Hast fill'd with bitt'rest grief his parents' hearts:
6150	Some solace might those hapless mourners find,
6150	Could I thy head and armour in the hands
6150	Of Panthous and of honour'd Phrontis place;
6150	Nor uncontested shall the proof remain,
6150	"Nor long deferr'd, of vict'ry or defeat."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and struck the centre of the shield,
6150	But broke not through; against the stubborn brass
6150	The point was bent; then with a pray'r to Jove
6150	The son of Atreus in his turn advanc'd;
6150	And, backward as he stepp'd, below his throat
6150	Took aim, and pressing hard with stalwart hand
6150	Drove through the yielding neck the pond'rous spear:
6150	Thund'ring he fell, and loud his armour rang.
6150	Those locks, that with the Graces' hair might vie,
6150	Those tresses bright, with gold and silver bound,
6150	Were dabbled all with blood. As when a man
6150	Hath rear'd a fair and vig'rous olive plant,
6150	In some lone spot, by copious-gushing springs,
6150	And seen expanding, nurs'd by ev'ry breeze,
6150	Its whit'ning blossoms; till with sudden gust
6150	A sweeping hurricane of wind and rain
6150	Uproots it from its bed, and prostrate lays;
6150	So lay the youthful son of Panthous, slain
6150	By Atreus' son, and of his arms despoil'd.
6150	And as a lion, in the mountains bred,
6150	In pride of strength, amid the pasturing herd
6150	Seizes a heifer in his pow'rful jaws,
6150	The choicest; and, her neck first broken, rends,
6150	And, on her entrails gorging, laps the blood;
6150	Though with loud clamour dogs and herdsmen round
6150	Assail him from afar, yet ventures none
6150	To meet his rage, for fear is on them all;
6150	So none was there so bold, with dauntless breast
6150	The noble Menelaus' wrath to meet.
6150	Now had Atrides borne away with ease
6150	The spoils of Panthous' son; but Phoebus grudg'd
6150	His prize of vict'ry, and against him launch'd
6150	The might of Hector, terrible as Mars:
6150	To whom his winged words, in Mentes' form,
6150	Chief of the Cicones, he thus address'd:
6150	""
6150	"""Hector, thy labour all is vain, pursuing"
6150	Pelides' flying steeds; and hard are they
6150	For mortal man to harness, or control.
6150	Save for Achilles' self, the Goddess-born.
6150	The valiant Menelaus, Atreus' son,
6150	Defends meanwhile Patroclus; and e'en now
6150	Hath slain a noble Trojan, Panthous' son,
6150	"Euphorbus, and his youthful vigour quell'd."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and join'd again the strife of men:
6150	Hector's dark soul with bitter grief was fill'd;
6150	He look'd amid the ranks, and saw the two,
6150	One slain, the other stripping off his arms,
6150	The blood outpouring from the gaping wound.
6150	Forward he sprang, in dazzling arms array'd,
6150	Loud shouting, blazing like the quenchless flames
6150	Of Vulcan: Menelaus heard the shout,
6150	And, troubled, commun'd with his valiant heart:
6150	""
6150	"""Oh, woe is me! for should I now the spoils"
6150	Abandon, and Patroclus, who for me
6150	And in my cause lies slain, of any Greek
6150	Who saw me, I might well incur the blame:
6150	And yet if here alone I dare to fight
6150	With Hector and his Trojans, much I fear,
6150	Singly, to be by numbers overwhelm'd;
6150	For Hector all the Trojans hither brings.
6150	But wherefore entertain such thoughts, my soul?
6150	Who strives, against the will divine, with one
6150	Belov'd of Heav'n, a bitter doom must meet.
6150	Then none may blame me, though I should retreat
6150	From Hector, who with Heav'n's assistance wars.
6150	Yet could I hear brave Ajax' battle cry,
6150	We two, returning, would the encounter dare,
6150	E'en against Heav'n, if so for Peleus' son
6150	We might regain, and bear away the dead:
6150	"Some solace of our loss might then be ours."""
6150	""
6150	While in his mind and spirit thus he mus'd,
6150	By Hector led, the Trojan ranks advanc'd:
6150	Backward he mov'd, abandoning the dead;
6150	But turning oft, as when by men and dogs
6150	A bearded lion from the fold is driv'n
6150	With shouts and spears; yet grieves his mighty heart,
6150	And with reluctant step he quits the yard:
6150	So from Patroclus Menelaus mov'd;
6150	Yet when he reach'd his comrades' ranks, he turn'd,
6150	And look'd around, if haply he might find
6150	The mighty Ajax, son of Telamon.
6150	Him on the battle's farthest left he spied,
6150	Cheering his friends and urging to the fight,
6150	For sorely Phoebus had their courage tried;
6150	And hast'ning to his side, address'd him thus:
6150	""
6150	"""Ajax, haste hither; to the rescue come"
6150	Of slain Patroclus; if perchance we two
6150	May to Achilles, Peleus' son, restore
6150	His body: his naked body, for his arms
6150	"Are prize to Hector of the glancing helm."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and Ajax' spirit within him stirr'd;
6150	Forward he sprang, and with him Atreus' son.
6150	Hector was dragging now Patroclus' corpse,
6150	Stripped of its glitt'ring armour, and intent
6150	The head to sever with his sword, and give
6150	The mangled carcase to the dogs of Troy:
6150	But Ajax, with his tow'r-like shield, approach'd;
6150	Then Hector to his comrades' ranks withdrew,
6150	Rush'd to his car, and bade the Trojans bear
6150	The glitt'ring arms, his glorious prize, to Troy:
6150	While Ajax with his mighty shield o'erspread
6150	Menoetius' son; and stood, as for his cubs
6150	A lion stands, whom hunters, unaware,
6150	Have with his offspring met amid the woods.
6150	Proud in his strength he stands; and down are drawn,
6150	Cov'ring his eyes, the wrinkles of his brow:
6150	So o'er Patroclus mighty Ajax stood,
6150	And by his side, his heart with grief oppress'd,
6150	The warlike Menelaus, Atreus' son.
6150	""
6150	Then Glaucus, leader of the Lycian host,
6150	To Hector thus, with scornful glance, address'd
6150	"His keen reproaches: ""Hector, fair of form,"
6150	How art thou wanting in the fight! thy fame,
6150	Coward and runaway, thou hast belied.
6150	Bethink thee now, if thou alone canst save
6150	The city, aided but by Trojans born;
6150	Henceforth no Lycian will go forth for Troy
6150	To fight with Greeks; since favour none we gain
6150	By unremitting toil against the foe.
6150	How can a meaner man expect thine aid,
6150	Who basely to the Greeks a prize and spoil
6150	Sarpedon leav'st, thy comrade and thy guest?
6150	Greatly he serv'd the city and thyself,
6150	While yet he liv'd; and now thou dar'st not save
6150	His body from the dogs! By my advice
6150	If Lycians will be rul'd, we take at once
6150	Our homeward way, and Troy may meet her doom.
6150	But if in Trojan bosoms there abode
6150	The daring, dauntless courage, meet for men
6150	Who in their country's cause against the foe
6150	Endure both toil and war, we soon should see
6150	Patroclus brought within the walls of Troy;
6150	Him from the battle could we bear away,
6150	And, lifeless, bring to royal Priam's town,
6150	Soon would the Greeks Sarpedon's arms release,
6150	And we to Ilium's heights himself might bear:
6150	For with his valiant comrades there lies slain
6150	The follower of the bravest chief of Greece.
6150	But thou before the mighty Ajax stood'st
6150	With downcast eyes, nor durst in manly fight
6150	"Contend with one thy better far confess'd."""
6150	""
6150	To whom thus Hector of the glancing helm,
6150	"With stern regard, replied: ""Why, Glaucus, speak,"
6150	Brave as thou art, in this o'erbearing strain?
6150	Good friend, I heretofore have held thee wise
6150	O'er all who dwell in Lycia's fertile soil;
6150	But now I change, and hold thy judgment cheap,
6150	Who chargest me with flying from the might
6150	Of giant Ajax; never have I shrunk
6150	From the stern fight, and clatter of the cars;
6150	But all o'erruling is the mind of Jove,
6150	Who strikes with panic, and of vict'ry robs
6150	The bravest; and anon excites to war.
6150	Stand by me now, and see if through the day
6150	I prove myself the coward that thou say'st,
6150	Or suffer that a Greek, how brave soe'er,
6150	"Shall rescue from my hands Patroclus' corpse."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and loudly on the Trojans call'd:
6150	"""Trojans and Lycians, and ye Dardans, fam'd"
6150	In close encounter, quit ye now like men;
6150	Maintain awhile the stubborn fight, while I
6150	The splendid armour of Achilles don,
6150	"My glorious prize from slain Patroclus torn."""
6150	""
6150	So saying, Hector of the glancing helm,
6150	Withdrawing from the field, with rapid steps
6150	His comrades follow'd, and ere long o'ertook,
6150	Who tow'rd the town Achilles' armour bore;
6150	Then standing from the bloody fight aloof
6150	The armour he exchang'd; his own he bade
6150	The warlike Trojans to the city bear;
6150	While he, of Peleus' son, Achilles, donn'd
6150	The heav'nly armour, which th' immortal Gods
6150	Gave to his sire; he to his son convey'd;
6150	Yet in that armour grew not old that son.
6150	""
6150	Him when apart the Cloud-compeller saw
6150	Girt with the arms of Peleus' godlike son,
6150	He shook his head, and inly thus he mus'd:
6150	"""Ah hapless! little deem'st thou of thy fate,"
6150	Though now so nigh! Thou of the prime of men,
6150	The dread of all, hast donn'd th' immortal arms,
6150	Whose comrade, brave and good, thy hand hath slain;
6150	And sham'd him, stripping from his head and breast
6150	Helmet and cuirass; yet thy latest hours
6150	Will I with glory crown; since ne'er from thee,
6150	Eeturn'd from battle, shall Andromache
6150	"Receive the spoils of Peleus' godlike son."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and nodded with his shadowy brows;
6150	Then with the armour, fitted to his form
6150	By Jove himself, was Hector girt by Mars
6150	The fierce and terrible; with vig'rous strength
6150	His limbs were strung, as 'mid his brave allies
6150	He sprang, loud-shouting; glitt'ring in his arms,
6150	To all he seem'd Achilles' godlike self.
6150	To each and all in cheering tones he spoke,
6150	Mesthles and Glaucus and Thersilochus,
6150	Asteropaeus and Hippothous,
6150	Medon, Deisenor, Phoreys, Chromius,
6150	And Ennomus the seer: to all of these
6150	His winged words he cheeringly address'd:
6150	""
6150	"""Hear me, ye countless tribes, that dwelling round"
6150	Assist our cause! You from your sev'ral homes
6150	Not for display of numbers have I call'd,
6150	But that with willing hearts ye should defend
6150	Our wives and infants from the warlike Greeks:
6150	For this I drain my people's stores, for food
6150	And gifts for you, exalting your estate;
6150	Then, who will boldly onward, he may fall,
6150	Or safe escape, such is the chance of war;
6150	But who within our valiant Trojans' ranks
6150	Shall but the body of Patroclus bring,
6150	Despite the might of Ajax; half the spoils
6150	To him I give, the other half myself
6150	"Retaining; and his praise shall equal mine."""
6150	""
6150	He said; and onward, with uplifted spears,
6150	They march'd upon the Greeks; high rose their hopes
6150	From Ajax Telamon to snatch the dead;
6150	Vain hopes, which cost them many a life! Then thus
6150	To valiant Menelaus Ajax spoke;
6150	""
6150	"""O Heav'n-born Menelaus, noble friend,"
6150	For safe return I dare no longer hope:
6150	Not for Patroclus' corpse so much I fear,
6150	Which soon will glut the dogs and birds of Troy,
6150	As for my life and thine I tremble now:
6150	For, like a war-cloud, Hector's might I see
6150	O'ershadowing all around; now is our doom
6150	Apparent; but do thou for succour call
6150	"On all the chiefs, if haply they may hear."""
6150	Thus Ajax spoke: obedient to his word,
6150	On all the chiefs Atrides call'd aloud:
6150	""
6150	"""O friends, the chiefs and councillors of Greece,"
6150	All ye that banquet at the gen'ral cost
6150	With Atreus' sons, and o'er your sev'ral states
6150	Dominion hold; whose honour is of Jove;
6150	'Twere hard to call by name each single man,
6150	So fierce the combat rages; but let each
6150	And all their aid afford, and deem, it shame
6150	"Patroclus' corpse should glut the dogs of Troy."""
6150	""
6150	He said: first heard Oileus' active son,
6150	And hast'ning through the fray, beside him stood.
6150	Next him Idomeneus, with whom there came,
6150	Valiant as Mars, his friend Meriones.
6150	But who can know or tell the names of all,
6150	Who, following, swell'd the battle of the Greeks?
6150	Onward the Trojans press'd, by Hector led:
6150	With such a sound, as when the ocean wave
6150	Meets on the beach th' outpouring of a stream,
6150	Swoll'n by the rains of Heav'n: the lofty cliffs
6150	Resound, and bellows the big sea without;
6150	With such a sound advanc'd the Trojan host:
6150	While round Patroclus, with one heart and mind,
6150	The Greeks a fence of brass-clad bucklers rais'd.
6150	O'er their bright helms the son of Saturn shed
6150	A veil of darkness; for Menoetius' son,
6150	Achilles' faithful friend, while yet he liv'd
6150	Jove hated not, nor would that now his corpse
6150	Should to the dogs of Troy remain a prey,
6150	But to the rescue all his comrades stirr'd.
6150	At first the Trojans drove the keen-ey'd Greeks;
6150	Leaving the corpse, they fled; nor with their spears
6150	The valiant Trojans reach'd a single Greek;
6150	But on the dead they seiz'd; yet not for long
6150	Endur'd their flight; them Ajax rallied soon,
6150	In form pre-eminent, and deeds of arms,
6150	O'er all the Greeks, save Peleus' matchless son.
6150	Onward he sprang, as springs a mountain boar,
6150	Which, turning in the forest glade to bay,
6150	Scatters with ease both dogs and stalwart youths;
6150	So Ajax scatter'd soon the Trojan ranks,
6150	That round Patroclus closing, hop'd to bear,
6150	With glory to themselves, his corpse to Troy.
6150	Hippothous, Pelasgian Lethus' son,
6150	Was dragging by the feet the noble dead,
6150	A leathern belt around his ancles bound,
6150	Seeking the favour of the men of Troy;
6150	But on himself he brought destruction down,
6150	Which none might turn aside; for from the crowd
6150	Outsprang the son of Telamon, and struck,
6150	In close encounter, on the brass-cheek'd helm;
6150	The plumed helm was shiver'd by the blow,
6150	Dealt by a weighty spear and stalwart hand;
6150	Gush'd from the wound the mingled blood and brain,
6150	His vital spirit quench'd; and on the ground
6150	Fell from his pow'rless grasp Patroclus' foot;
6150	While he himself lay stretch'd beside the dead,
6150	Far from his own Larissa's teeming soil:
6150	Not destin'd he his parents to repay
6150	Their early care; for short his term of life,
6150	By godlike Ajax' mighty spear subdu'd.
6150	""
6150	At Ajax Hector threw his glitt'ring spear:
6150	He saw, and narrowly the brazen death
6150	Escap'd; but Schedius, son of Iphitus,
6150	(The bravest of the Phocian chiefs, who dwelt
6150	In far-fam'd Panopeus, the mighty Lord
6150	Of num'rous hosts,) below the collar-bone
6150	It struck, and passing through, the brazen point
6150	Came forth again beneath his shoulder-blade:
6150	Thund'ring he fell, and loud his armour rang.
6150	""
6150	As Phorcys, son of Phaenops, kept his watch
6150	O'er slain Hippothous, him Ajax smote
6150	Below the waist; the weighty spear broke through
6150	The hollow breastplate, and th' intestines tore;
6150	Prone in the dust he fell, and clutch'd the ground.
6150	At this the Trojan chiefs and Hector's self
6150	'Gan to give way; the Greeks, with joyful shouts,
6150	Seiz'd both the dead, and stripp'd their armour off.
6150	To Ilium now, before the warlike Greeks,
6150	O'ercome by panic, had the Trojans fled;
6150	And now had Greeks, despite the will of Jove,
6150	By their own strength and courage, won the day,
6150	Had not Apollo's self AEneas rous'd,
6150	In likeness of a herald, Periphas,
6150	The son of Epytus, now aged grown
6150	In service of AEneas' aged sire,
6150	A man of kindliest soul: his form assum'd
6150	Apollo, and AEneas thus address'd:
6150	""
6150	"""AEneas, how, against the will of Heav'n,"
6150	Could ye defend your city, as others now
6150	In their own strength and courage confident,
6150	Their numbers, and their troops' undaunted hearts,
6150	I see their cause maintaining; if when Jove
6150	Rather to us than them the vict'ry wills,
6150	"With fear unspeakable ye shun the fight?"""
6150	""
6150	He said: the presence of the Archer-God
6150	AEneas knew, and loud to Hector call'd:
6150	"""Hector, and all ye other chiefs of Troy,"
6150	And brave Allies, foul shame it were that we,
6150	O'ercome by panic, should to Ilium now
6150	In flight be driv'n before the warlike Greeks;
6150	And by my side, but now, some God there stood,
6150	And told how Jove, the sov'reign arbiter
6150	Of battle, on our side bestow'd his aid;
6150	On then! nor undisturbed allow the Greeks
6150	"To bear Patroclus' body to their ships."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and far before the ranks advanc'd;
6150	They rallying turn'd, and fac'd again the Greeks.
6150	Then first AEneas' spear the comrade brave
6150	Of Lycomedes struck, Laocritus,
6150	Son of Arisbas; Lycomedes saw
6150	With pitying eyes his gallant comrade's fall;
6150	And standing near, his glitt'ring spear he threw,
6150	And through the midriff Apisaon struck,
6150	His people's guardian chief, the valiant son
6150	Of Hippasus, and slack'd his limbs in death.
6150	He from Paeonia's fertile fields had come,
6150	O'er all his comrades eminent in fight,
6150	All save Asteropaeus, who with eyes
6150	Of pity saw his gallant comrade's fall,
6150	And forward sprang to battle with the Greeks;
6150	Yet could not force his way; for all around
6150	Patroclus rose a fence of serried shields,
6150	And spears projecting: such the orders giv'n
6150	By Ajax, and with earnest care enforc'd;
6150	That from around the dead should none retire,
6150	Nor any to the front advance alone
6150	Before his fellows; but their steady guard
6150	Maintain, and hand to hand the battle wage.
6150	So order'd Ajax; then with crimson blood
6150	The earth was wet; and hand to hand they fell,
6150	Trojans alike, and brave Allies, and Greeks;
6150	For neither these a bloodless fight sustain'd,
6150	Though fewer far their losses; for they stood
6150	Of mutual succour mindful, and support.
6150	Thus, furious as the rage of fire, they fought;
6150	Nor might ye deem the glorious sun himself
6150	Nor moon was safe; for darkest clouds of night
6150	O'erspread the warriors, who the battle wag'd
6150	Around the body of Menoetius' son:
6150	Elsewhere the Trojans and the well-greav'd Greeks
6150	Fought, undisturb'd, in the clear light of day;
6150	The sun's bright beams were shed abroad; no cloud
6150	Lay on the face of earth or mountain tops;
6150	They but by fits, at distant intervals,
6150	And far apart, each seeking to avoid
6150	The hostile missiles, fought; but in the midst
6150	The bravest all, in darkness and in strife
6150	Sore press'd, toil'd on beneath their armour's weight.
6150	""
6150	As yet no tidings of Patroclus' fall
6150	Had reach'd two valiant chiefs, Antilochus
6150	And Thrasymedes; but they deem'd him still
6150	Alive, and fighting in the foremost ranks.
6150	They, witnessing their comrades' flight and death,
6150	Fought on apart, by Nestor so enjoin'd,
6150	When from the ships he bade them join the fray.
6150	Great was meanwhile their labour, who sustain'd,
6150	Throughout the livelong day, that weary fight;
6150	Reek'd with continuous toil and sweat, the knees,
6150	And legs and feet, the arms, and eyes, of all
6150	Who round Achilles' faithful comrade fought.
6150	As when a chief his people bids to stretch
6150	A huge bull's hide, all drench'd and soak'd with grease;
6150	They in a circle rang'd, this way and that,
6150	Pull the tough hide, till ent'ring in, the grease
6150	Is all absorb'd; and dragg'd by num'rous hands
6150	The supple skin to th' utmost length is stretch'd;
6150	So these in narrow space this way and that
6150	The body dragg'd; and high the hopes of each
6150	To bear it off in triumph; to their ships
6150	The Greeks, to Troy the Trojans; fiercely rag'd
6150	The struggle; spirit-stirring Mars himself,
6150	Or Pallas to her utmost fury rous'd,
6150	Had not that struggle with contempt beheld:
6150	Such grievous labour o'er Patroclus' corpse
6150	Had Jove to horses and to men decreed.
6150	""
6150	But of Patroclus' fall no tidings yet
6150	Had reach'd Achilles; for the war was wag'd
6150	Far from the ships, beneath the walls of Troy;
6150	Nor look'd he of his death to hear, but deem'd
6150	That when the Trojans to their gates were driv'n,
6150	He would return in safety; for no hope
6150	Had he of taking by assault the town,
6150	With, or without, his aid; for oft apart
6150	His Goddess-mother had his doom, foretold,
6150	Revealing to her son the mind of Jove;
6150	Yet ne'er had warn'd him of such grief as this,
6150	Which now befell, his dearest comrade's loss.
6150	""
6150	Still round the dead they held their pointed spears,
6150	Fought hand to hand, and mutual slaughter dealt;
6150	And thus perchance some brass-clad Greek would say:
6150	""
6150	"""O friends, 'twere shameful should we to the ships"
6150	Ingloriously return; ere that should be,
6150	Let earth engulph us all; so better far
6150	Than let these Trojans to their city bear
6150	"Our dead, and boast them of their triumph gain'd."""
6150	On th' other hand some valiant Trojan thus
6150	"Would shout: ""O friends, tho' fate decreed that here"
6150	"We all should die, yet let not one give way."""
6150	""
6150	Thus, cheering each his comrades, would they speak,
6150	And thus they fought; the iron clangour pierc'd
6150	The empty air, and brazen vault of Heav'n.
6150	But, from the fight withdrawn, Achilles' steeds
6150	Wept, as they heard how in the dust was laid
6150	Their charioteer, by Hector's murd'rous hand.
6150	Automedon, Diores' valiant son,
6150	Essay'd in vain to rouse them with the lash,
6150	In vain with honey'd words, in vain with threats;
6150	Nor to the ships would they return again
6150	By the broad Hellespont, nor join the fray;
6150	But as a column stands, which marks the tomb
6150	Of man or woman, so immovable
6150	Beneath the splendid car they stood, their heads
6150	Down-drooping to the ground, while scalding tears
6150	Dropp'd earthward from their eyelids, as they mourn'd
6150	Their charioteer; and o'er the yoke-band shed
6150	Down stream'd their ample manes, with dust defil'd.
6150	The son of Saturn pitying saw their grief,
6150	And sorrowing shook his head, as thus he mus'd:
6150	""
6150	"""Ah, hapless horses! wherefore gave we you"
6150	To royal Peleus, to a mortal man,
6150	You that from age and death are both exempt!
6150	Was it that you the miseries might share
6150	Of wretched mortals? for of all that breathe,
6150	And walk upon the earth, or creep, is nought
6150	More wretched than th' unhappy race of man.
6150	Yet shall not ye, nor shall your well-wrought car,
6150	By Hector, son of Priam, be controll'd;
6150	I will not suffer it; enough for him
6150	To hold, with vaunting boast, Achilles' arms;
6150	But to your limbs and spirits will I impart
6150	Such strength, that from the battle to the ships
6150	Ye shall in safety bear Automedon;
6150	For yet I will the Trojans shall prevail,
6150	And slay, until they reach the well-mann'd ships,
6150	"Till sets the sun, and darkness shrouds the earth."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and in their breasts fresh spirit infus'd;
6150	They, shaking from their manes the dust, the car
6150	Amid the Greeks and Trojans lightly bore.
6150	Then, as a vulture 'mid a flock of geese,
6150	Amid the battle rush'd Automedon,
6150	His horses' course directing, and their speed
6150	Exciting, though he mourn'd his comrade slain.
6150	Swiftly he fled from out the Trojan host;
6150	Swiftly again assail'd them in pursuit;
6150	Yet, speedy to pursue, he could not slay;
6150	Nor, in the car alone, had pow'r at once
6150	To guide the flying steeds, and hurl the spear.
6150	At length a comrade brave, Alcimedon,
6150	Laerces' son, beheld; behind the car
6150	He stood, and thus Automedon address'd:
6150	"""Automedon, what God has fill'd thy mind"
6150	With counsels vain, and thee of sense bereft?
6150	That with the Trojans, in the foremost ranks,
6150	Thou fain wouldst fight alone, thy comrade slain,
6150	While Hector proudly on his breast displays
6150	"The glorious arms of great AEacides."""
6150	""
6150	To whom Automedon, Diores' son:
6150	"""Alcimedon, since none of all the Greeks"
6150	May vie with thee, the mettle to control
6150	Of these immortal horses, save indeed,
6150	While yet he liv'd, Patroclus, godlike chief;
6150	But him stern death and fate have overta'en;
6150	Take thon the whip and shining reins, while I,
6150	"Descending from the car, engage in fight."""
6150	""
6150	He said; and, mounting on the war-car straight,
6150	Alcimedon the whip and reins assum'd;
6150	Down leap'd Automedon; great Hector saw,
6150	And thus address'd AEneas at his side:
6150	""
6150	"""AEneas, prince and counsellor of Troy,"
6150	I see, committed to unskilful hands,
6150	Achilles' horses on the battle-field:
6150	These we may hope to take, if such thy will;
6150	For they, methinks, will scarcely stand oppos'd,
6150	"Or dare th' encounter of our joint assault."""
6150	""
6150	He said; Anchises' valiant son complied;
6150	Forward they went, their shoulders cover'd o'er
6150	With stout bull's-hide, thick overlaid with brass.
6150	With them both Chromius and Aretus went;
6150	""
6150	And high their hopes were rais'd, the warriors both
6150	To slay, and make the strong-neck'd steeds their prize:
6150	Blind fools! nor destin'd scatheless to escape
6150	Automedon's encounter; he his pray'r
6150	To Jove address'd, and straight with added strength
6150	His soul was fill'd; and to Alcimedon,
6150	His trusty friend and comrade, thus he spoke:
6150	""
6150	"""Alcimedon, do thou the horses keep"
6150	Not far away, but breathing on my neck;
6150	For Hector's might will not, I deem, be stay'd,
6150	Ere us he slay, and mount Achilles' car,
6150	And carry terror 'mid the Grecian host,
6150	"Or in the foremost ranks himself be slain."""
6150	""
6150	Thus spoke Automedon, and loudly call'd
6150	On Menelaus and th' Ajaces both:
6150	"""Ye two Ajaces, leaders of the host,"
6150	And, Menelaus, with our bravest all,
6150	Ye on the dead alone your care bestow,
6150	To guard him, and stave off the hostile ranks;
6150	But haste, and us, the living, save from death;
6150	For Hector and AEneas hitherward,
6150	With weight o'erpow'ring, through the bloody press,
6150	The bravest of the Trojans, force their way:
6150	Yet is the issue in the hands of Heav'n;
6150	"I hurl the spear, but Jove directs the blow."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and, poising, hurl'd the pond'rous spear;
6150	Full on Aretus' broad-orb'd shield it struck;
6150	Nor stay'd the shield its course; the brazen point
6150	Drove through the belt, and in his body lodg'd.
6150	As with sharp axe in hand a stalwart man,
6150	Striking behind the horns a sturdy bull,
6150	Severs the neck; he, forward, plunging, falls;
6150	So forward first he sprang, then backwards fell:
6150	And quiv'ring, in his vitals deep infix'd,
6150	The sharp spear soon relax'd his limbs in death.
6150	Then at Automedon great Hector threw
6150	His glitt'ring spear; he saw, and forward stoop'd,
6150	And shunn'd the brazen death; behind him far
6150	Deep in the soil infix'd, with quiv'ring shaft
6150	The weapon stood; there Mars its impulse stay'd.
6150	And now with swords, and hand to hand, the fight
6150	Had been renew'd; but at their comrade's call
6150	The two Ajaces, pressing through the throng,
6150	Between the warriors interpos'd in haste.
6150	Before them Hector and AEneas both,
6150	And godlike Chromius, in alarm recoil'd;
6150	Pierc'd through the heart, Aretus there they left;
6150	And, terrible as Mars, Automedon
6150	Stripp'd off his arms, and thus exulting cried:
6150	"""Of some small portion of its load of grief,"
6150	For slain Patroclus, is my heart reliev'd,
6150	"In slaying thee, all worthless as thou art."""
6150	""
6150	Then, throwing on the car the bloody spoils,
6150	He mounted, hands and feet imbrued with blood,
6150	As 'twere a lion, fresh from his repast
6150	Upon the carcase of a slaughter'd bull.
6150	""
6150	Again around Patroclus' body rag'd
6150	The stubborn conflict, direful, sorrow-fraught:
6150	From Heav'n descending, Pallas stirr'd the strife,
6150	Sent by all-seeing Jove to stimulate
6150	The warlike Greeks; so changed was now his will.
6150	As o'er the face of Heav'n when Jove extends
6150	His bright-hued bow, a sign to mortal men
6150	Of war, or wintry storms, which bid surcease
6150	The rural works of man, and pinch the flocks;
6150	So Pallas, in a bright-hued cloud array'd,
6150	Pass'd through the ranks, and rous'd each sev'ral man.
6150	To noble Menelaus, Atreus' son,
6150	Who close beside her stood, the Goddess first,
6150	The form of Phoenix and his pow'rful voice
6150	Assuming, thus her stirring words address'd:
6150	""
6150	"""On thee, O Menelaus, foul reproach"
6150	Will fasten, if Achilles' faithful friend
6150	The dogs devour beneath the walls of Troy;
6150	"Then hold thou firm, and all the host inspire."""
6150	""
6150	To whom thus Menelaus, good in fight:
6150	"""O Phoenix, aged warrior, honour'd sire,"
6150	If Pallas would the needful pow'r impart,
6150	And o'er me spread her aegis, then would I
6150	Undaunted for Patroclus' rescue fight,
6150	For deeply by his death my heart is touch'd;
6150	But valiant Hector, with the strength of fire
6150	Still rages, and destruction deals around:
6150	"For Jove is with him, and his triumph wills."""
6150	""
6150	He said: the blue-ey'd Goddess heard with joy
6150	That, chief of all the Gods, her aid he sought.
6150	She gave fresh vigour to his arms and knees,
6150	And to his breast the boldness of the fly,
6150	Which, oft repell'd by man, renews th' assault
6150	Incessant, lur'd by taste of human blood;
6150	Such boldness in Atrides' manly breast
6150	Pallas inspir'd: beside Patroclus' corpse
6150	Again he stood, and pois'd his glitt'ring spear.
6150	""
6150	There was one Podes in the Trojan ranks,
6150	Son of Eetion, rich, of blameless life,
6150	Of all the people most to Hector dear,
6150	And at his table oft a welcome guest:
6150	Him, as he turn'd to fly, beneath the waist
6150	Atrides struck; right through the spear was driv'n;
6150	Thund'ring he fell; and Atreus' son the corpse
6150	Dragg'd from the Trojans 'mid the ranks of Greece.
6150	""
6150	Then close at Hector's side Apollo stood,
6150	Clad in the form of Phaenops, Asius' son,
6150	Who in Abydos dwelt; of all th' Allies
6150	Honour'd of Hector most, and best belov'd;
6150	Clad in his form, the Far-destroyer spoke:
6150	""
6150	"""Hector, what other Greek will scare thee next?"
6150	Who shrink'st from Menelaus, heretofore
6150	A warrior deem'd of no repute; but now,
6150	Alone, he robs our Trojans of their dead;
6150	And in the foremost ranks e'en now hath slain
6150	"Podes, thine own good friend, Eetion's son."""
6150	""
6150	He said; dark grief o'erclouded Hector's brow,
6150	As to the front in dazzling arms he sprang.
6150	Then Saturn's son his tassell'd aegis wav'd,
6150	All glitt'ring bright; and Ida's lofty head
6150	In clouds and darkness shrouded; then he bade
6150	His lightning flash, his volleying thunder roar,
6150	That shook the mountain; and with vict'ry crown'd
6150	The Trojan arms, and panic-struck the Greeks.
6150	""
6150	The first who turn'd to fly was Peneleus,
6150	Boeotian chief; him, facing still the foe,
6150	A spear had slightly on the shoulder struck,
6150	The bone just grazing: by Polydamas,
6150	Who close before him stood, the spear was thrown.
6150	Then Hector Leitus, Aloctryon's son,
6150	Thrust thro' the wrist, and quell'd his warlike might;
6150	Trembling, he look'd around, nor hop'd again
6150	The Trojans, spear in hand, to meet in fight;
6150	But, onward as he rush'd on Leitus,
6150	Idomeneus at Hector threw his spear:
6150	Full on his breast it struck; but near the head
6150	The sturdy shaft was on the breastplate snapp'd:
6150	Loud was the Trojans' shout; and he in turn
6150	Aim'd at Idomeneus, Deucalion's son,
6150	Upstanding on his car; his mark he miss'd,
6150	But Coeranus he struck, the charioteer
6150	And faithful follower of Meriones,
6150	Who with him came from Lyctus' thriving town:
6150	The chief had left on foot the well-trimm'd ships;
6150	And, had not Coeranus his car in haste
6150	Driv'n to the rescue, by his fall had giv'n
6150	A Trojan triumph; to his Lord he brought
6150	Safety, and rescue from unsparing death;
6150	But fell, himself, by Hector's murd'rous hand.
6150	Him Hector struck between the cheek and ear,
6150	Crashing the teeth, and cutting through the tongue.
6150	Headlong he fell to earth, and dropp'd the reins:
6150	These, stooping from the car, Meriones
6150	Caught up, and thus Idomeneus address'd:
6150	""
6150	"""Ply now the lash, until thou reach the ships:"
6150	"Thyself must see how crush'd the strength of Greece."""
6150	""
6150	He said; and tow'rd the ships Idomeneus
6150	Urg'd his fleet steeds; for fear was on his soul.
6150	Nor did not Ajax and Atrides see
6150	How in the Trojans' favour Saturn's son
6150	The wav'ring scale of vict'ry turn'd; and thus
6150	Great Ajax Telamon his grief express'd:
6150	""
6150	"""O Heav'n! the veriest child might plainly see"
6150	That Jove the Trojans' triumph has decreed:
6150	Their weapons all, by whomsoever thrown,
6150	Or weak, or strong, attain their mark; for Jove
6150	Directs their course; while ours upon the plain
6150	Innocuous fall. But take we counsel now
6150	How from the fray to bear away our dead,
6150	And by our own return rejoice those friends
6150	Who look with sorrow on our plight, and deem
6150	That we, all pow'rless to resist the might
6150	Of Hector's arm, beside the ships must fall.
6150	Would that some comrade were at hand, to bear
6150	A message to Achilles; him, I ween,
6150	As yet the mournful tidings have not reach'd,
6150	That on the field his dearest friend lies dead.
6150	But such I see not; for a veil of cloud
6150	O'er men and horses all around is spread.
6150	O Father Jove, from, o'er the sons of Greece
6150	Remove this cloudy darkness; clear the sky,
6150	That we may see our fate, and die at least,
6150	"If such thy will, in th' open light of day."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and, pitying, Jove beheld his tears;
6150	The clouds he scatter'd, and the mist dispers'd;
6150	The sun shone forth, and all the field was clear;
6150	Then Ajax thus to Menelaus spoke:
6150	""
6150	"""Now, Heav'n-born Menelaus, look around"
6150	If haply 'mid the living thou mayst see
6150	Antilochus, the noble Nester's son;
6150	And bid him to Achilles bear in haste
6150	"The tidings, that his dearest friend lies dead."""
6150	""
6150	He said, nor did Atrides not comply;
6150	But slow as moves a lion from the fold,
6150	Which dogs and youths with ceaseless toil hath worn,
6150	Who all night long have kept their watch, to guard
6150	From his assault the choicest of the herd;
6150	He, hunger-pinch'd, hath oft th' attempt renew'd,
6150	But nought prevail'd; by spears on ev'ry side,
6150	And jav'lins met, wielded by stalwart hands,
6150	And blazing torches, which his courage daunt;
6150	Till with the morn he sullenly withdraws;
6150	So from Patroclus, with reluctant step
6150	Atrides mov'd; for much he fear'd the Greeks
6150	Might to the Trojans, panic-struck, the dead
6150	Abandon; and departing, he besought
6150	The two Ajaces and Meriones:
6150	"""Ye two Ajaces, leaders of the Greeks,"
6150	And thou, Meriones, remember now
6150	Our lost Patroclus' gentle courtesy,
6150	How kind and genial was his soul to all,
6150	"While yet he liv'd--now sunk, alas! in death."""
6150	""
6150	Thus saying, Menelaus took his way,
6150	Casting his glance around on ev'ry side,
6150	Like to an eagle, fam'd of sharpest sight
6150	Of all that fly beneath the vault of Heav'n;
6150	Whom, soaring in the clouds, the crouching hare
6150	Eludes not, though in leafiest covert hid;
6150	But swooping down, he rends her life away:
6150	So, Menelaus, through the ranks of war
6150	Thy piercing glances ev'ry way were turn'd,
6150	If Nestor's son, alive, thou mightst descry;
6150	Him on the field's extremest left he found,
6150	Cheering his friends, and urging to the fight;
6150	He stood beside him, and address'd him thus:
6150	""
6150	"""Antilochus, come hither, godlike friend,"
6150	And woful tidings hear, which would to Heav'n
6150	I had not to impart; thyself thou seest
6150	How Jove hath heap'd disaster on the Greeks,
6150	And vict'ry giv'n to Troy; but one has fallen,
6150	Our bravest, best! Patroclus lies in death;
6150	And deeply must the Greeks his loss deplore.
6150	But haste thee to the ships, to Peleus' son
6150	The tidings bear, if haply he may save
6150	The body of Patroclus from the foe;
6150	His naked body, for his arms are now
6150	"The prize of Hector of the glancing helm."""
6150	""
6150	He said; and at his words Antilochus
6150	Astounded stood; long time his tongue in vain
6150	For utt'rance strove; his eyes were fill'd with tears,
6150	His cheerful voice was mute; yet not the less
6150	To Menelaus' bidding gave his care:
6150	Swiftly he sped; but to Laodocus,
6150	His comrade brave, who waited with his car
6150	In close attendance, first consign'd his arms;
6150	Then from the field with active limbs he flew,
6150	Weeping, with mournful news, to Peleus' son.
6150	Nor, noble Menelaus, did thy heart
6150	Incline thee to remain, and aid thy friends,
6150	Where from their war-worn ranks the Pylian troops
6150	Deplor'd the absence of Antilochus;
6150	But these in godlike Thrasymedes' charge
6150	He left; and to Patroclus hast'ning back,
6150	Beside th' Ajaces stood, as thus he spoke:
6150	"""Him to Achilles, to the ships, in haste"
6150	I have despatch'd; yet fiercely as his wrath
6150	May burn tow'rd Hector, I can scarce expect
6150	His presence here; for how could he, unarm'd,
6150	With Trojans fight? But take we counsel now
6150	How from the field to bear away our dead,
6150	"And 'scape ourselves from death by Trojan hands."""
6150	""
6150	Whom answer'd thus great Ajax Telamon:
6150	"""Illustrious Menelaus, all thy words"
6150	Are just and true; then from amid the press,
6150	Thou and Meriones, take up in haste,
6150	And bear away the body; while behind
6150	We two, in heart united, as in name,
6150	Who side by side have still been wont to fight,
6150	"Will Hector and his Trojans hold at bay."""
6150	""
6150	He said; they, lifting in their arms the corpse,
6150	Uprais'd it high in air; then from behind
6150	Loud yell'd the Trojans, as they saw the Greeks
6150	Retiring with their dead; and on they rush'd,
6150	As dogs that in advance of hunter youths
6150	Pursue a wounded boar; awhile they run,
6150	Eager for blood; but when, in pride of strength,
6150	He turns upon them, backward they recoil,
6150	This way and that in fear of death dispers'd:
6150	So onward press'd awhile the Trojan crowd,
6150	With thrust of swords, and double-pointed spears;
6150	But ever as th' Ajaces turn'd to bay,
6150	Their colour chang'd to pale, not one so bold
6150	As, dashing on, to battle for the corpse.
6150	Thus they, with anxious care, from off the field
6150	Bore tow'rd the ships their dead; but on their track
6150	Came sweeping on the storm of battle, fierce,
6150	As, on a sudden breaking forth, the fire
6150	Seizes some populous city, and devours
6150	House after house amid the glare and blaze,
6150	While roar the flames before the gusty wind;
6150	So fiercely pressed upon the Greeks' retreat
6150	The clatt'ring tramp of steeds and armed men.
6150	But as the mules, with stubborn strength endued,
6150	That down the mountain through the trackless waste
6150	Drag some huge log, or timber for the ships;
6150	And spent with toil and sweat, still labour on
6150	Unflinching; so the Greeks with patient toil
6150	Bore on their dead; th' Ajaces in their rear
6150	Stemming the war, as stems the torrent's force
6150	Some wooded cliff, far stretching o'er the plain;
6150	Checking the mighty river's rushing stream,
6150	And flinging it aside upon the plain,
6150	Itself unbroken by the strength of flood:
6150	So firmly, in the rear, th' Ajaces stemm'd
6150	The Trojan force; yet these still onward press'd,
6150	And, 'mid their comrades proudly eminent,
6150	Two chiefs, AEneas, old Anchises' son,
6150	And glorious Hector, in the van were seen.
6150	Then, as a cloud of starlings or of daws
6150	Fly screaming, as they see the hawk approach,
6150	To lesser birds the messenger of death;
6150	So before Hector and AEneas fled,
6150	Screaming, forgetful of their warlike fame,
6150	The sons of Greece; and scatter'd here and there
6150	Around the ditch lay store of goodly arms,
6150	By Greeks abandon'd in their hasty flight.
6150	Yet still, unintermitted, rag'd the war.
6150	""

6150	""
6150	ARGUMENT.
6150	""
6150	THE GRIEF OF ACHILLES, AND NEW ARMOUR MADE HIM BY VULCAN.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	The news of the death of Patroclus is brought to Achilles by
6150	Antilochus. Thetis hearing his lamentations, comes with all her sea-
6150	nymphs to comfort him. The speeches of the mother and son on this
6150	occasion. Iris appears to Achilles by command of Juno, and orders him
6150	to show himself at the head of the intrenchments. The sight of him
6150	turns the fortune of the day, and the body of Patroclus is carried off
6150	by the Greeks. The Trojans call a council, where Hector and Polydamas
6150	disagree in their opinions; but the advice of the former prevails, to
6150	remain encamped in the field. The grief of Achilles over the body of
6150	Patroclus.
6150	""
6150	Thetis goes to the palace of Vulcan, to obtain new arms for her son.
6150	The description of the wonderful works of Vulcan; and, lastly, that
6150	noble one of the shield of Achilles.
6150	""
6150	The latter part of the nine-and-twentieth day, and the night ensuing,
6150	take up this book. The scene is at Achilles' tent on the seashore, from
6150	whence it changes to the palace of Vulcan.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	BOOK XVIII.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	Thus, furious as the rage of fire, they fought.
6150	Meantime Antilochus to Peleus' son,
6150	Swift-footed messenger, his tidings bore.
6150	Him by the high-beak'd ships he found, his mind
6150	Th' event presaging, fill'd with anxious thoughts,
6150	As thus he commun'd with his mighty heart:
6150	""
6150	"""Alas! what means it, that the long-hair'd Greeks,"
6150	Chas'd from the plain, are thronging round the ships?
6150	Let me not now, ye Gods, endure the grief
6150	My mother once foretold, that I should live
6150	To see the bravest of the Myrmidons
6150	Cut off by Trojans from the light of day.
6150	Menoetius' noble son has surely fall'n;
6150	Foolhardy! yet I warn'd him, and besought,
6150	Soon as the ships from hostile fires were safe,
6150	"Back to return, nor Hector's onset meet."""
6150	""
6150	While in his mind and spirit thus he mus'd,
6150	Beside him stood the noble Nestor's son,
6150	And weeping, thus his mournful message gave:
6150	""
6150	"""Alas! great son of Peleus, woful news,"
6150	Which would to Heav'n I had not to impart,
6150	To thee I bring; Patroclus lies in death;
6150	And o'er his body now the war is wag'd;
6150	His naked body, for his arms are now
6150	"The prize of Hector of the glancing helm."""
6150	""
6150	He said; and darkest clouds of grief o'erspread
6150	Achilles' brow; with both his hands he seiz'd
6150	And pour'd upon his head the grimy dust,
6150	Marring his graceful visage; and defil'd
6150	With black'ning ashes all his costly robes.
6150	Stretch'd in the dust his lofty stature lay,
6150	As with his hands his flowing locks he tore;
6150	Loud was the wailing of the female band,
6150	Achilles' and Patroclus' prize of war,
6150	As round Achilles, rushing out of doors,
6150	Beating their breasts, with tott'ring limbs they press'd.
6150	In tears beside him stood Antilochus,
6150	And in his own Achilles' hand he held,
6150	Groaning in spirit, fearful lest for grief
6150	In his own bosom he should sheathe his sword.
6150	Loud were his moans; his Goddess-mother heard,
6150	Beside her aged father where she sat
6150	In the deep ocean caves; she heard, and wept:
6150	The Nereids all, in ocean's depths who dwell,
6150	Encircled her around; Cymodoce,                                 [5]
6150	Nesaee, Spio, and Cymothoe,
6150	The stag-ey'd Halia, and Amphithoe,
6150	Actaea, Limnorea, Melite,
6150	Doris, and Galatea, Panope;
6150	There too were Oreithyia, Clymene,
6150	And Amathea with the golden hair,
6150	And all the denizens of ocean's depths.
6150	Fill'd was the glassy cave; in unison
6150	They beat their breasts, as Thetis led the wail:
6150	""
6150	"""Give ear, my sister Nereids all, and learn"
6150	How deep the grief that in my breast I bear.
6150	Me miserable! me, of noblest son
6150	Unhappiest mother! me, a son who bore,
6150	My brave, my beautiful, of heroes chief!
6150	Like a young tree he throve: I tended him,
6150	In a rich vineyard as the choicest plant;
6150	Till in the beaked ships I sent him forth
6150	To war with Troy; him ne'er shall I behold,
6150	Returning home, in aged Peleus' house.
6150	E'en while he lives, and sees the light of day,
6150	He lives in sorrow; nor, to soothe his grief,
6150	My presence can avail; yet will I go,
6150	That I may see my dearest child, and learn
6150	"What grief hath reach'd him, from the war withdrawn."""
6150	""
6150	She said, and left the cave; with her they went,
6150	Weeping; before them parted th' ocean wave.
6150	But when they reach'd the fertile shore of Troy,
6150	In order due they landed on the beach,
6150	Where frequent, round Achilles swift of foot,
6150	Were moor'd the vessels of the Myrmidons.
6150	There, as he groan'd aloud, beside him stood
6150	His Goddess-mother; weeping, in her hands
6150	She held his head, while pitying thus she spoke:
6150	""
6150	"""Why weeps my son? and what his cause of grief?"
6150	Speak out, and nought conceal; for all thy pray'r
6150	Which with uplifted hands thou mad'st to Jove,
6150	He hath fulfill'd, that, flying to their ships,
6150	The routed sons of Greece should feel how much
6150	"They need thine aid, and mourn their insult past."""
6150	""
6150	To whom Achilles, deeply groaning, thus:
6150	"""Mother, all this indeed hath Jove fulfill'd;"
6150	Yet what avails it, since my dearest friend
6150	Is slain, Patroclus? whom I honour'd most
6150	Of all my comrades, lov'd him as my soul.
6150	Him have I lost: and Hector from his corpse
6150	Hath stripp'd those arms, those weighty, beauteous arms,
6150	A marvel to behold, which from the Gods
6150	Peleus receiv'd, a glorious gift, that day
6150	When they consign'd thee to a mortal's bed.
6150	How better were it, if thy lot had been
6150	Still 'mid the Ocean deities to dwell,
6150	And Peleus had espous'd a mortal bride!
6150	For now is bitter grief for thee in store,
6150	Mourning thy son; whom to his home return'd
6150	Thou never more shalt see; nor would I wish
6150	To live, and move amid my fellow-men,
6150	Unless that Hector, vanquish'd by my spear,
6150	May lose his forfeit life, and pay the price
6150	"Of foul dishonour to Patroclus done."""
6150	""
6150	To whom, her tears o'erflowing, Thetis thus:
6150	"""E'en as thou sayst, my son, thy term is short;"
6150	"Nor long shall Hector's fate precede thine own."""
6150	""
6150	Achilles, answ'ring, spoke in passionate grief:
6150	"""Would I might die this hour, who fail'd to save"
6150	My comrade slain! far from his native land
6150	He died, sore needing my protecting arm;
6150	And I, who ne'er again must see my home,
6150	Nor to Patroclus, nor the many Greeks
6150	Whom Hector's hand hath slain, have render'd aid;
6150	But idly here I sit, cumb'ring the ground:
6150	I, who amid the Greeks no equal own
6150	In fight; to others, in debate, I yield.
6150	Accurs'd of Gods and men be hateful strife
6150	And anger, which to violence provokes
6150	E'en temp'rate souls: though sweeter be its taste
6150	Than dropping honey, in the heart of man
6150	Swelling, like smoke; such anger in my soul
6150	Hath Agamemnon kindled, King of men.
6150	But pass we that; though still my heart be sore,
6150	Yet will I school my angry spirit down.
6150	In search of Hector now, of him who slew
6150	My friend, I go; prepar'd to meet my death,
6150	When Jove shall will it, and th' Immortals all.
6150	From death not e'en the might of Hercules,
6150	Though best belov'd of Saturn's son, could fly,
6150	By fate and Juno's bitter wrath subdued.
6150	I too, since such my doom, must lie in death;
6150	Yet, ere I die, immortal fame will win;
6150	And from their delicate cheeks, deep-bosom'd dames,
6150	Dardan and Trojan, bitter tears shall wipe,
6150	And groan in anguish; then shall all men know
6150	How long I have been absent from the field;
6150	Then, though thou love me, seek not from the war
6150	"To stay my steps; for bootless were thy speech."""
6150	""
6150	Whom answer'd thus the silver-footed Queen:
6150	"""True are thy words, my son; and good it is,"
6150	And commendable, from the stroke of death
6150	To save a worsted comrade; but thine arms,
6150	Thy brazen, flashing arms, the Trojans hold:
6150	Them Hector of the glancing helm himself
6150	Bears on his breast, exulting; yet not long
6150	Shall be his triumph, for his doom is nigh.
6150	But thou, engage not in the toils of war,
6150	Until thine eyes again behold me here;
6150	For with to-morrow's sun will I return
6150	"With arms of heav'nly mould, by Vulcan wrought."""
6150	""
6150	Thus saying, from her son she turn'd away,
6150	And turning, to her sister Nereids spoke:
6150	"""Back to the spacious bosom of the deep"
6150	Retire ye now; and to my father's house,
6150	The aged Ocean God, your tidings bear;
6150	While I to high Olympus speed, to crave
6150	At Vulcan's hand, the skill'd artificer,
6150	"A boon of dazzling armour for my son."""
6150	""
6150	She said; and they beneath the ocean wave
6150	Descended, while to high Olympus sped
6150	The silver-footed Goddess, thence in hope
6150	To bear the dazzling armour to her son.
6150	She to Olympus sped; the Greeks meanwhile
6150	Before the warrior-slayer Hector fled
6150	With wild, tumultuous uproar, till they reach'd
6150	Their vessels and the shore of Hellespont.
6150	Nor had the well-greav'd Greets Achilles' friend,
6150	Patroclus, from amid the fray withdrawn;
6150	For close upon him follow'd horse and man,
6150	And Hector, son of Priam, fierce as flame;
6150	Thrice noble Hector, seizing from behind,
6150	Sought by the feet to drag away the dead,
6150	Cheering his friends; thrice, clad in warlike might,
6150	The two Ajaces drove him from his prey.
6150	Yet, fearless in his strength, now rushing on
6150	He dash'd amid the fray; now, shouting loud,
6150	Stood firm; but backward not a step retir'd.
6150	As from a carcase herdsmen strive in vain
6150	To scare a tawny lion, hunger-pinch'd;
6150	E'en so th' Ajaces, mail-clad warriors, fail'd
6150	The son of Priam from the corpse to scare.
6150	And now the body had he borne away,
6150	With endless fame; but from Olympus' height
6150	Came storm-swift Iris down to Peleus' son,
6150	And bade him don his arms; by Juno sent,
6150	Unknown to Jove, and to th' Immortals all.
6150	She stood beside him, and address'd him thus:
6150	""
6150	"""Up, son of Peleus! up, thou prince of men!"
6150	Haste to Patroclus' rescue; whom, around,
6150	Before the ships, is wag'd a fearful war,
6150	With mutual slaughter; these the dead defending,
6150	And those to Ilium's breezy heights intent
6150	To bear the body; noble Hector chief,
6150	Who longs to sever from the tender neck,
6150	And fix upon the spikes, thy comrade's head.
6150	Up then! delay no longer; deem it shame
6150	Patroclus' corpse should glut the dogs of Troy,
6150	"Dishon'ring thee, if aught dishonour him."""
6150	""
6150	Whom answer'd thus Achilles, swift of foot:
6150	"""Say, heav'nly Iris, of th' immortal Gods"
6150	"Who bade thee seek me, and this message bring?"""
6150	""
6150	"To whom swift Iris thus: ""To thee I come"
6150	By Juno sent, th' imperial wife of Jove;
6150	Unknown to Saturn's son, and all the Gods
6150	"Who on Olympus' snowy summit dwell."""
6150	""
6150	To whom again Achilles, swift of foot:
6150	"""How in the battle toil can I engage?"
6150	My arms are with the Trojans; and to boot
6150	My mother warn'd me not to arm for fight,
6150	Till I again should see her; for she hop'd
6150	To bring me heav'nly arms by Vulcan wrought:
6150	Nor know I well whose armour I could wear,
6150	Save the broad shield of Ajax Telamon
6150	And he, methinks, amid the foremost ranks
6150	"Ev'n now is fighting o'er Patroclus' corpse."""
6150	""
6150	"Whom answer'd storm-swift Iris: ""Well we know"
6150	Thy glorious arms are by the Trojans held;
6150	But go thou forth, and from above the ditch
6150	Appear before them; daunted at the sight,
6150	Haply the Trojans may forsake the field,
6150	And breathing-time afford the sons of Greece,
6150	"Toil-worn; for little pause has yet been theirs."""
6150	""
6150	Swift Iris said, and vanish'd; then uprose
6150	Achilles, dear to Jove; and Pallas threw
6150	Her tassell'd aegis o'er his shoulders broad;
6150	His head encircling with a coronet
6150	Of golden cloud, whence fiery flashes gleam'd.
6150	As from an island city up to Heav'n
6150	The smoke ascends, which hostile forces round
6150	Beleaguer, and all day with cruel war
6150	From its own state cut off; but when the sun
6150	Hath set, blaze frequent forth the beacon fires;
6150	High rise the flames, and to the dwellers round
6150	Their signal flash, if haply o'er the sea
6150	May come the needful aid; so brightly flash'd
6150	That fiery light around Achilles' head.
6150	He left the wall, and stood above the ditch,
6150	But from the Greeks apart, rememb'ring well
6150	His mother's prudent counsel; there he stood,
6150	And shouted loudly; Pallas join'd her voice,
6150	And fill'd with terror all the Trojan host.
6150	Clear as the trumpet's sound, which calls to arms
6150	Some town, encompass'd round with hostile bands,
6150	Rang out the voice of great AEacides.
6150	But when Achilles' voice of brass they heard,
6150	They quail'd in spirit; the sleek-skin'd steeds themselves,
6150	Conscious of coming ill, bore back the cars:
6150	Their charioteers, dismay'd, beheld the flame
6150	Which, kindled by the blue-ey'd Goddess, blaz'd
6150	Unquench'd around the head of Peleus' son.
6150	Thrice shouted from the ditch the godlike chief;
6150	Thrice terror struck both Trojans and Allies;
6150	And there and then beside their chariots fell
6150	Twelve of their bravest; while the Greeks, well pleas'd,
6150	Patroclus' body from the fray withdrew,
6150	And on a litter laid; around him stood
6150	His comrades mourning; with them, Peleus' son,
6150	Shedding hot tears, as on his friend he gaz'd,
6150	Laid on the bier, and pierc'd with deadly wounds:
6150	Him to the war with horses and with cars
6150	He sent; but ne'er to welcome his return.
6150	By stag-ey'd Juno sent, reluctant sank
6150	Th' unwearied sun beneath the ocean wave;
6150	The sun had set, and breath'd awhile the Greeks
6150	From the fierce labours of the balanc'd field;
6150	Nor less the Trojans, from the stubborn fight
6150	Retiring, from the chariots loos'd their steeds:
6150	But ere they shar'd the ev'ning meal, they met
6150	In council; all stood up; none dar'd to sit;
6150	For fear had fallen on all, when reappear'd
6150	Achilles, from the battle long withdrawn.
6150	First Panthous' son, the sage Polydamas,
6150	Address'd th' assembly; his sagacious mind
6150	Alone beheld the future and the past;
6150	The friend of Hector, born the selfsame night;
6150	One in debate, the other best in arms;
6150	Who thus with prudent speech began, and said:
6150	""
6150	"""Be well advis'd, my friends! my counsel is"
6150	That we regain the city, nor the morn
6150	Here in the plain, beside the ships, await,
6150	So far remov'd from our protecting walls.
6150	While fiercely burn'd 'gainst Atreus' godlike son
6150	That mighty warrior's wrath, 'twas easier far
6150	With th' other Greeks to deal; and I rejoic'd
6150	When by the ships we pass'd the night, in hopes
6150	We soon might call them ours; but now, I own
6150	Achilles, swift of foot, excites my fear.
6150	His proud, impetuous spirit will spurn the plain,
6150	Where Greeks and Trojans oft in warlike strife
6150	Their balanc'd strength exert; if he come forth,
6150	Our fight will be to guard our homes and wives.
6150	Gain we the city; trust me, so 'twere best.
6150	Now, for a while, ambrosial night detains
6150	The son of Peleus; but at early morn
6150	If issuing forth in arms he find us here,
6150	His prowess we shall know; and happy he
6150	Who, flying, shall in safety reach the walls
6150	Of sacred Troy; for many a Trojan slain
6150	Shall feed the vultures; Heav'n avert such fate!
6150	But if, though loth, ye will by me be rul'd,
6150	This night in council husband we our strength;
6150	While tow'rs, and lofty gates, and folding doors
6150	Close join'd, well-fitting, shall our city guard:
6150	Then issuing forth in arms at early morn
6150	Man we the tow'rs; so harder were his task
6150	If, from the ships advancing, round the wall
6150	He offer battle; bootless to return,
6150	His strong-neck'd horses worn with labour vain
6150	In coursing, purposeless, around the town.
6150	To force an entrance, or the town destroy,
6150	Is not his aim; and ere that end be gain'd,
6150	"The dogs of Troy upon his flesh shall feed."""
6150	""
6150	To whom thus Hector of the glancing helm
6150	"With stern regard: ""Polydamas, thy words"
6150	Are such as grate unkindly on mine ear,
6150	Who fain wouldst have us to the walls retire.
6150	What? have ye not already long enough
6150	Been coop'd within the tow'rs? the wealth of Troy,
6150	Its brass, its gold, were once the common theme
6150	Of ev'ry tongue; our hoarded treasures now
6150	Are gone, to Phrygian and Maeonian shores
6150	For sale exported, costly merchandise,
6150	Since on our city fell the wrath of Jove.
6150	And now, when deep-designing Saturn's son
6150	Such glory gives me as to gain the ships,
6150	And, crowded by the sea, hem in the Greeks,
6150	Fool! put not thou these timid counsels forth,
6150	Which none will follow, nor will I allow.
6150	But hear ye all, and do as I advise:
6150	Share now the meal, by ranks, throughout the host;
6150	Then set your watch, and each keep careful guard;
6150	And whom his spoils o'erload, if such there be,
6150	Let him divide them with the gen'ral crowd;
6150	Better that they should hold them than the Greeks:
6150	And with the morn, in arms, beside the ships,
6150	Will we again awake the furious war.
6150	But if indeed Achilles by the ships
6150	Hath reappear'd, himself, if so he choose,
6150	Shall be the suff'rer; from the perilous strife
6150	I will not shrink, but his encounter meet:
6150	So he, or I, shall gain immortal fame;
6150	"Impartial Mars hath oft the slayer slain."""
6150	""
6150	Thus Hector spoke; the Trojans cheer'd aloud:
6150	Fools, and by Pallas of their sense bereft,
6150	Who all applauded Hector's ill advice,
6150	None the sage counsel of Polydamas!
6150	Then through the camp they shar'd the ev'ning meal.
6150	""
6150	Meantime the Greeks all night with tears and groans
6150	Bewail'd Patroclus: on his comrade's breast
6150	Achilles laid his murder-dealing hands,
6150	And led with bitter groans the loud lament.
6150	As when the hunters, in the forest's depth,
6150	Have robb'd a bearded lion of his cubs;
6150	Too late arriving, he with anger chafes;
6150	Then follows, if perchance he may o'ertake,
6150	Through many a mountain glen, the hunters' steps,
6150	With grief and fury fill'd; so Peleus' son,
6150	With bitter groans, the Myrmidons address'd:
6150	""
6150	"""Vain was, alas! the promise which I gave,"
6150	Seeking the brave Menoetius to console,
6150	To bring to Opus back his gallant son,
6150	Rich with his share of spoil from Troy o'erthrown;
6150	But Jove fulfils not all that man designs:
6150	For us hath fate decreed, that here in Troy
6150	We two one soil should redden with our blood;
6150	Nor me, returning to my native land,
6150	Shall aged Peleus in his halls receive,
6150	Nor Thetis; here must earth retain my bones.
6150	But since, Patroclus, I am doom'd on earth
6150	Behind thee to remain, thy fun'ral rites
6150	I will not celebrate, till Hector's arms,
6150	And head, thy haughty slayer's, here I bring;
6150	And on thy pyre twelve noble sons of Troy
6150	Will sacrifice, in vengeance of thy death.
6150	Thou by our beaked ships till then must lie;
6150	And weeping o'er thee shall deep-bosom'd dames,
6150	Trojan and Dardan, mourn both night and day;
6150	The prizes of our toil, when wealthy towns
6150	"Before our valour and our spears have fall'n."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and bade his comrades on the fire
6150	An ample tripod place, without delay
6150	To cleanse Patroclus from the bloody gore:
6150	They on the burning fire the tripod plac'd,
6150	With water fill'd, and kindled wood beneath.
6150	Around the bellying tripod rose the flames,
6150	Heating the bath; within the glitt'ring brass
6150	Soon as the water boil'd, they wash'd the corpse,
6150	With lissom oils anointing, and the wounds
6150	With fragrant ointments fill'd, of nine years old;
6150	Then in fine linen they the body wrapp'd
6150	From head to feet, and laid it on a couch.
6150	And cover'd over with a fair white sheet.
6150	All night around Achilles swift of foot
6150	The Myrmidons with tears Patroclus mourn'd.
6150	""
6150	To Juno then, his sister and his wife,
6150	"Thus Saturn's son: ""At length thou hast thy will,"
6150	Imperial Juno, who hast stirr'd to war
6150	Achilles swift of foot; well might one deem
6150	"These long-hair'd Greeks from thee deriv'd their birth."""
6150	""
6150	To whom in answer thus the stag-ey'd Queen:
6150	"""What words, dread son of Saturn, dost thou speak?"
6150	E'en man, though mortal, and inferior far
6150	To us in wisdom, might so much effect
6150	Against his fellow-man; then how should I,
6150	By double title chief of Goddesses,
6150	First by my birth, and next because thy wife
6150	I boast me, thine, o'er all the Gods supreme,
6150	"Not work my vengeance on the Trojan race?"""
6150	""
6150	Such, converse while they held, to Vulcan's house,
6150	Immortal, starlike bright, among the Gods
6150	Unrivall'd, all of brass, by Vulcan's self
6150	Constructed, sped the silver-footed Queen.
6150	Him swelt'ring at his forge she found, intent
6150	On forming twenty tripods, which should stand
6150	The wall surrounding of his well-built house;
6150	With golden wheels beneath he furnish'd each,
6150	And to th' assembly of the Gods endued
6150	With pow'r to move spontaneous, and return,
6150	A marvel to behold! thus far his work
6150	He had completed; but not yet had fix'd
6150	The rich-wrought handles; these his labour now
6150	Engag'd, to fit them, and to rivet fast.
6150	While thus he exercis'd his practis'd skill,
6150	The silver-footed Queen approach'd the house.
6150	Charis, the skilful artist's wedded wife,
6150	Beheld her coming, and advanc'd to meet;
6150	And, as her hand she clasp'd, address'd her thus:
6150	""
6150	"""Say, Thetis of the flowing robe, belov'd"
6150	And honour'd, whence this visit to our house,
6150	An unaccustom'd guest? but come thou in,
6150	"That I may welcome thee with honour due."""
6150	""
6150	Thus, as she spoke, the Goddess led her in,
6150	And on a seat with silver studs adorn'd,
6150	Fair, richly wrought, a footstool at her feet,
6150	She bade her sit; then thus to Vulcan call'd:
6150	"""Haste hither, Vulcan; Thetis asks thine aid."""
6150	""
6150	Whom answer'd thus the skill'd artificer:
6150	"""An honour'd and a venerated guest"
6150	Our house contains; who sav'd me once from woe,
6150	When by my mother's act from Heav'n I fell,
6150	Who, for that I was crippled in my feet,
6150	Deem'd it not shame to hide me: hard had then
6150	My fortune been, had not Eurynome
6150	And Thetis in their bosoms shelter'd me;
6150	Eurynome, from old Oceanus
6150	Who drew her birth, the ever-circling flood.
6150	Nine years with them I dwelt, and many a work
6150	I fashion'd there of metal, clasps, and chains
6150	Of spiral coil, rich cups, and collars fair,
6150	Hid in a cave profound; where th' ocean stream
6150	With ceaseless murmur foam'd and moan'd around;
6150	Unknown to God or man, but to those two
6150	Who sav'd me, Thetis and Eurynome.
6150	Now to my house hath fair-hair'd Thetis come;
6150	To her, my life preserv'd its tribute owes:
6150	Then thou the hospitable rites perform.
6150	"While I my bellows and my tools lay by."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and from the anvil rear'd upright
6150	His massive strength; and as he limp'd along,
6150	His tottering knees were bow'd beneath his weight.
6150	The bellows from the fire he next withdrew,
6150	And in a silver casket plac'd his tools;
6150	Then with a sponge his brows and lusty arms
6150	He wip'd, and sturdy neck and hairy chest.
6150	He donn'd his robe, and took his weighty staff;
6150	Then through the door with halting step he pass'd;
6150	There waited on their King the attendant maids;
6150	In form as living maids, but wrought in gold;
6150	Instinct with consciousness, with voice endued,
6150	And strength, and skill from heav'nly teachers drawn.
6150	These waited, duteous, at the Monarch's side,
6150	His steps supporting; he, with halting gait,
6150	Pass'd to a gorgeous chair by Thetis' side,
6150	And, as her hand he clasp'd, address'd her thus:
6150	""
6150	"""Say, Thetis of the flowing robe, belov'd"
6150	And honour'd, whence this visit to our house.
6150	An unaccustom'd guest? say what thy will,
6150	"And, if within my pow'r, esteem it done."""
6150	""
6150	To whom in answer Thetis, weeping, thus:
6150	"""Vulcan, of all the Goddesses who dwell"
6150	On high Olympus, lives there one whose soul
6150	Hath borne such weight of woe, so many griefs,
6150	As Saturn's son hath heap'd on me alone?
6150	Me, whom he chose from all the sea-born nymphs,
6150	And gave to Peleus, son of AEacus,
6150	His subject; I endur'd a mortal's bed,
6150	Though sore against my will; he now, bent down
6150	By feeble age, lies helpless in his house.
6150	Now adds he farther grief; he granted me
6150	To bear, and rear, a son, of heroes chief;
6150	Like a young tree he throve; I tended him,
6150	In a rich vineyard as the choicest plant:
6150	Till in the beaked ships I sent him forth
6150	To war with Troy; him ne'er shall I receive,
6150	Returning home, in aged Peleus' house.
6150	E'en while he lives, and sees the light of day,
6150	He lives in sorrow; nor, to soothe his grief,
6150	My presence can avail; a girl, his prize,
6150	Selected for him by the sons of Greece,
6150	Great Agamemnon wrested from his arms:
6150	In grief and rage he pin'd his soul away;
6150	Then by the Trojans were the Greeks hemm'd in
6150	Beside their ships, and from within their camp
6150	No outlet found; the Grecian Elders then
6150	Implor'd his aid, and promis'd costly gifts.
6150	With his own hand to save them he refus'd;
6150	But, in his armour clad, to battle sent
6150	His friend Patroclus, with a num'rous band.
6150	All day they fought before the Scaean* gates;
6150	And in that day had Ilium been destroy'd,
6150	But in the van, Menoetius' noble son.
6150	After great deeds achiev'd, Apollo slew,
6150	And crown'd with glory Hector, Priam's son.
6150	Therefore a suppliant to thy knees I come,
6150	If to my son, to early death condemn'd,
6150	Thou wilt accord the boon of shield and helm,
6150	And well-wrought greaves with silver clasps secur'd,
6150	And breastplate; for his own, his faithful friend,
6150	By Trojan hands subdued, hath lost; and he,
6150	"O'erwhelm'd with grief, lies prostrate on the earth."""
6150	""
6150	Whom answer'd thus the skill'd artificer:
6150	"""Take comfort, nor let this disturb thy mind;"
6150	Would that as surely, when his hour shall come,
6150	I could defend him from the stroke of death,
6150	As I can undertake that his shall be
6150	"Such arms as they shall marvel who behold."""
6150	""
6150	He left her thus, and to his forge return'd;
6150	The bellows then directing to the fire,
6150	He bade them work; through twenty pipes at once
6150	Forthwith they pour'd their diverse-temper'd blasts;
6150	Now briskly seconding his eager haste,
6150	Now at his will, and as the work requir'd.
6150	The stubborn brass, and tin, and precious gold,
6150	And silver, first he melted in the fire,
6150	Then on its stand his weighty anvil plac'd;
6150	And with one hand the hammer's pond'rous weight
6150	He wielded, while the other grasp'd the tongs.
6150	""
6150	And first a shield he fashion'd, vast and strong,
6150	With rich adornment; circled with a rim,
6150	Threefold, bright-gleaming, whence a silver belt
6150	Depended; of five folds the shield was form'd;
6150	And on its surface many a rare design
6150	Of curious art his practis'd skill had wrought.
6150	""
6150	Thereon were figur'd earth, and sky, and sea,
6150	The ever-circling sun, and full-orb'd moon,
6150	And all the signs that crown the vault of Heav'n;
6150	Pleiads and Hyads, and Orion's might,
6150	And Arctos, call'd the Wain, who wheels on high
6150	His circling course, and on Orion waits;
6150	Sole star that never bathes in th' ocean wave.
6150	""
6150	And two fair populous towns were sculptur'd there;
6150	In one were marriage pomp and revelry.
6150	And brides, in gay procession, through the streets
6150	With blazing torches from their chambers borne,
6150	While frequent rose the hymeneal song.
6150	Youths whirl'd around in joyous dance, with sound
6150	Of flute and harp; and, standing at their doors,
6150	Admiring women on the pageant gaz'd.
6150	""
6150	Meanwhile a busy throng the forum fill'd:
6150	There between two a fierce contention rose,
6150	About a death-fine; to the public one
6150	Appeal'd, asserting to have paid the whole;
6150	While one denied that he had aught receiv'd.
6150	Both were desirous that before the Judge
6150	The issue should be tried; with noisy shouts
6150	Their several partisans encourag'd each.
6150	The heralds still'd the tumult of the crowd:
6150	On polish'd chairs, in solemn circle, sat
6150	The rev'rend Elders; in their hands they held
6150	The loud-voic'd heralds' sceptres; waving these,
6150	They heard th' alternate pleadings; in the midst
6150	Two talents lay of gold, which he should take
6150	Who should before them prove his righteous cause.
6150	""
6150	Before the second town two armies lay,
6150	In arms refulgent; to destroy the town
6150	Th' assailants threaten'd, or among themselves
6150	Of all the wealth within the city stor'd
6150	An equal half, as ransom, to divide.
6150	The terms rejecting, the defenders mann'd
6150	A secret ambush; on the walls they plac'd
6150	Women and children muster'd for defence,
6150	And men by age enfeebled; forth they went,
6150	By Mars and Pallas led; these, wrought in gold,
6150	In golden arms array'd, above the crowd
6150	For beauty and stature, as befitting Gods,
6150	Conspicuous shone; of lesser height the rest.
6150	But when the destin'd ambuscade was reach'd,
6150	Beside the river, where the shepherds drove
6150	Their flocks and herds to water, down they lay,
6150	In glitt'ring arms accoutred; and apart
6150	They plac'd two spies, to notify betimes
6150	Th' approach of flocks of sheep and lowing herds.
6150	These, in two shepherds' charge, ere long appear'd,
6150	Who, unsuspecting as they mov'd along,
6150	Enjoy'd the music of their past'ral pipes.
6150	They on the booty, from afar discern'd,
6150	Sprang from their ambuscade; and cutting off
6150	The herds, and fleecy flocks, their guardians slew.
6150	Their comrades heard the tumult, where they sat
6150	Before their sacred altars, and forthwith
6150	Sprang on their cars, and with fast-stepping steeds
6150	Pursued the plund'rers, and o'ertook them soon.
6150	There on the river's bank they met in arms,
6150	And each at other hurl'd their brazen spears.
6150	And there were figur'd Strife, and Tumult wild,
6150	And deadly Fate, who in her iron grasp
6150	One newly-wounded, one unwounded bore,
6150	While by the feet from out the press she dragg'd
6150	Another slain: about her shoulders hung
6150	A garment crimson'd with the blood of men.
6150	Like living men they seem'd to move, to fight,
6150	To drag away the bodies of the slain.
6150	""
6150	And there was grav'n a wide-extended plain
6150	Of fallow land, rich, fertile, mellow soil,
6150	Thrice plough'd; where many ploughmen up and down
6150	Their teams were driving; and as each attain'd
6150	The limit of the field, would one advance,
6150	And tender him a cup of gen'rous wine:
6150	Then would he turn, and to the end again
6150	Along the furrow cheerly drive his plough.
6150	And still behind them darker show'd the soil,
6150	The true presentment of a new-plough'd field,
6150	Though wrought in gold; a miracle of art.
6150	""
6150	There too was grav'n a corn-field, rich in grain,
6150	Where with sharp sickles reapers plied their task,
6150	And thick, in even swathe, the trusses fell;
6150	The binders, following close, the bundles tied:
6150	Three were the binders; and behind them boys
6150	In close attendance waiting, in their arms
6150	Gather'd the bundles, and in order pil'd.
6150	Amid them, staff in hand, in silence stood
6150	The King, rejoicing in the plenteous swathe.
6150	A little way remov'd, the heralds slew
6150	A sturdy ox, and now beneath an oak
6150	Prepar'd the feast; while women mix'd, hard by,
6150	White barley porridge for the lab'rers' meal.
6150	""
6150	And, with rich clusters laden, there was grav'n
6150	A vineyard fair, all gold; of glossy black
6150	The bunches were, on silver poles sustain'd;
6150	Around, a darksome trench; beyond, a fence
6150	Was wrought, of shining tin; and through it led
6150	One only path, by which the bearers pass'd,
6150	Who gather'd in the vineyard's bounteous store.
6150	There maids and youths, in joyous spirits bright,
6150	In woven baskets bore the luscious fruit.
6150	A boy, amid them, from a clear-ton'd harp
6150	Drew lovely music; well his liquid voice
6150	The strings accompanied; they all with dance
6150	And song harmonious join'd, and joyous shouts,
6150	As the gay bevy lightly tripp'd along.
6150	""
6150	Of straight-horn'd cattle too a herd was grav'n;
6150	Of gold and tin the heifers all were wrought:
6150	They to the pasture, from the cattle-yard,
6150	With gentle lowings, by a babbling stream,
6150	Where quiv'ring reed-beds rustled, slowly mov'd.
6150	Four golden shepherds walk'd beside the herd,
6150	By nine swift dogs attended; then amid
6150	The foremost heifers sprang two lions fierce
6150	Upon the lordly bull: he, bellowing loud,
6150	Was dragg'd along, by dogs and youths pursued.
6150	The tough bull's-hide they tore, and gorging lapp'd
6150	Th' intestines and dark blood; with vain attempt
6150	The herdsmen following closely, to the attack
6150	Cheer'd their swift dogs; these shunn'd the lions' jaws,
6150	And close around them baying, held aloof.
6150	""
6150	And there the skilful artist's hand had trac'd
6150	A pastaro broad, with fleecy flocks o'erspread,
6150	In a fair glade, with fold, and tents, and pens.
6150	""
6150	There, too, the skilful artist's hand had wrought
6150	With curious workmanship, a mazy dance,
6150	Like that which Daedalus in Cnossus erst
6150	At fair-hair'd Ariadne's bidding fram'd.
6150	There, laying each on other's wrists their hand,
6150	Bright youths and many-suitor'd maidens danc'd:
6150	In fair white linen these; in tunics those,
6150	Well woven, shining soft with fragrant oils;
6150	These with fair coronets were crown'd, while those
6150	With golden swords from silver belts were girt.
6150	Now whirl'd they round with nimble practis'd feet,
6150	Easy, as when a potter, seated, turns
6150	A wheel, new fashion'd by his skilful hand,
6150	And spins it round, to prove if true it run;
6150	Now featly mov'd in well-beseeming ranks.
6150	A num'rous crowd, around, the lovely dance
6150	Survey'd, delighted; while an honour'd Bard
6150	Sang, as he struck the lyre, and to the strain
6150	Two tumblers, in the midst, were whirling round.
6150	""
6150	About the margin of the massive shield
6150	Was wrought the mighty strength of th' ocean stream.
6150	""
6150	The shield completed, vast and strong, he forg'd
6150	A breastplate, dazzling bright as flame of fire;
6150	And next, a weighty helmet for his head,
6150	Fair, richly wrought, with crest of gold above;
6150	Then last, well-fitting greaves of pliant tin.
