6150	""
6150	The skill'd artificer his works complete
6150	Before Achilles' Goddess-mother laid:
6150	She, like a falcon, from the snow-clad heights
6150	Of huge Olympus, darted swiftly down,
6150	Charg'd with the glitt'ring arms by Vulcan wrought.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	ARGUMENT
6150	""
6150	THE RECONCILIATION OF ACHILLES AND AGAMEMNON.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	Thetis brings to her son the armour made by Vulcan. She preserves the
6150	body of his friend from corruption, and commands him to assemble the
6150	army, to declare his resentment at an end. Agamemnon and Achilles are
6150	solemnly reconciled: the speeches, presents, and ceremonies on that
6150	occasion. Achilles is with great difficulty persuaded to refrain from
6150	the battle till the troops have refreshed themselves, by the advice of
6150	Ulysses. The presents are conveyed to the tent of Achilles: where
6150	Briseis laments over the body of Patroclus. The hero obstinately
6150	refuses all repast, and gives himself up to lamentations for his
6150	friend. Minerva descends to strengthen him, by the order of Jupiter. He
6150	arms for the fight; his appearance described. He addresses himself to
6150	his horses, and reproaches them with the death of Patroclus. One of
6150	them is miraculously endued with voice, and inspired to prophesy his
6150	fate; but the hero, not astonished by that prodigy, rushes with fury to
6150	the combat.
6150	""
6150	The thirtieth day. The scene is on the sea-shore.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	BOOK XIX.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	Now morn in saffron robe, from th' ocean stream
6150	Ascending, light diffus'd o'er Gods and men;
6150	As Thetis, to the ships returning, bore
6150	The gift of Vulcan; there her son she found,
6150	Who o'er Patroclus hung in bitter grief;
6150	Around him mourn'd his comrades; in the midst
6150	She stood, and clasp'd his hand, as thus she spoke:
6150	""
6150	"""Leave we, my son, though deep our grief, the dead;"
6150	Here let him lie, since Heav'n hath doom'd his fall;
6150	But thou these arms receive, by Vulcan sent,
6150	"Fairer than e'er on mortal breast were borne."""
6150	The arms before Achilles, as she spoke,
6150	The Goddess laid; loud rang the wondrous work.
6150	With awe the Myrmidons beheld; nor dar'd
6150	Affront the sight: but as Achilles gaz'd,
6150	More fiery burn'd his wrath; beneath his brows
6150	His eyes like lightning flash'd; with fierce delight
6150	He seiz'd the glorious gift: and when his soul
6150	Had feasted on the miracle of art,
6150	To Thetis thus his winged words address'd:
6150	""
6150	"""Mother, the God hath giv'n me arms indeed,"
6150	Worthy a God, and such as mortal man
6150	Could never forge; I go to arm me straight;
6150	Yet fear I for Menoetius' noble son,
6150	Lest in his spear-inflicted wounds the flies
6150	May gender worms, and desecrate the dead,
6150	"And, life extinct, corruption reach his flesh."""
6150	""
6150	Whom answer'd thus the silver-footed Queen:
6150	"""Let not such fears, my son, disturb thy mind:"
6150	I will myself the swarms of flies disperse,
6150	That on the flesh of slaughter'd warriors prey:
6150	And should he here remain a year complete,
6150	Still should his flesh be firm and fresh as now:
6150	But thou to council call the chiefs of Greece;
6150	Against the monarch Agamemnon there,
6150	The leader of the host, abjure thy wrath;
6150	"Then arm thee quickly, and put on thy might."""
6150	""
6150	Her words with dauntless courage fill'd his breast.
6150	She in Patroclus' nostrils, to preserve
6150	His flesh, red nectar and ambrosia pour'd.
6150	""
6150	Along the ocean beach Achilles pass'd,
6150	And loudly shouting, call'd on all the chiefs;
6150	Then all who heretofore remain'd on board,
6150	The steersmen, who the vessels' rudders hold,
6150	The very stewards that serv'd the daily bread,
6150	All to th' assembly throng'd, when reappear'd
6150	Achilles, from the fight so long withdrawn.
6150	Two noble chiefs, two ministers of Mars,
6150	Ulysses sage, and valiant Diomed,
6150	Appear'd, yet crippled by their grievous wounds,
6150	Their halting steps supporting with their spears,
6150	And on the foremost seats their places took.
6150	Next follow'd Agamemnon, King of men,
6150	He also wounded; for Antenor's son,
6150	Coon, had stabb'd him in the stubborn fight.
6150	When all the Greeks were closely throng'd around,
6150	Up rose Achilles swift of foot, and said:
6150	""
6150	"""Great son of Atreus, what hath been the gain"
6150	To thee or me, since heart-consuming strife
6150	Hath fiercely rag'd between us, for a girl,
6150	Who would to Heav'n had died by Dian's shafts
6150	That day when from Lyrnessus' captur'd town
6150	I bore her off? so had not many a Greek
6150	Bitten the bloody dust, by hostile hands
6150	Subdued, while I in anger stood aloof.
6150	Great was the gain to Troy; but Greeks, methinks,
6150	Will long retain the mem'ry of our feud.
6150	Yet pass we that; and though our hearts be sore,
6150	Still let us school our angry spirits down.
6150	My wrath I here abjure; it is not meet
6150	It burn for ever unappeas'd; do thou
6150	Muster to battle straight the long-hair'd Greeks;
6150	That, to the Trojans once again oppos'd,
6150	I may make trial if beside the ships
6150	They dare this night remain; but he, I ween,
6150	Will gladly rest his limbs, who safe shall fly,
6150	"My spear escaping, from the battle-field."""
6150	""
6150	He said: the well-greav'd Greeks rejoic'd to hear
6150	His wrath abjur'd by Peleus' godlike son;
6150	And from his seat, not standing in the midst,
6150	Thus to th' assembly Agamemnon spoke:
6150	"""Friends, Grecian Heroes, Ministers of Mars,"
6150	When one stands up to speak, 'tis meet for all
6150	To lend a patient ear, nor interrupt;
6150	For e'en to practis'd speakers hard the task:
6150	But, in this vast assembly, who can speak
6150	That all may hear? the clearest voice must fail.
6150	To Peleus' son, Achilles, I my mind
6150	Will frankly open; ye among yourselves
6150	Impart the words I speak, that all may know.
6150	Oft hath this matter been by Greeks discuss'd,
6150	And I their frequent censure have incurr'd:
6150	Yet was not I the cause; but Jove, and Fate,
6150	And gloomy Erinnys, who combin'd to throw
6150	A strong delusion o'er my mind, that day
6150	I robb'd Achilles of his lawful prize.
6150	What could I do? a Goddess all o'er-rul'd,
6150	Daughter of Jove, dread Ate, baleful pow'r,
6150	Misleading all; with lightest step she moves,
6150	Not on the earth, but o'er the heads of men,
6150	With blighting touch; and many hath caus'd to err.
6150	E'en Jove, the wisest deem'd of Gods and men,
6150	In error she involv'd, when Juno's art
6150	By female stratagem the God deceiv'd,
6150	When in well-girdled Thebes Alcmena lay
6150	In travail of the might of Hercules.
6150	In boastful tone amid the Gods he spoke:
6150	'Hear all ye Gods, and all ye Goddesses,
6150	The words I speak, the promptings of my soul.
6150	This day Lucina shall to light bring forth
6150	A child, the future Lord of all around,
6150	Of mortal men, who trace to me their blood.'
6150	Whom answer'd Juno thus, with deep deceit:
6150	'Thou dost but feign, nor wilt fulfil thy word:
6150	Come now, Olympian, swear a solemn oath
6150	That he shall be the Lord of all around,
6150	Who on this day shall be of woman born,
6150	Of mortal men, who trace to thee their blood.'
6150	She said, and Jove, the snare unseeing, swore
6150	A solemn oath; but found his error soon.
6150	Down from Olympus' height she sped in haste
6150	To Argos of Achaia; for the wife
6150	Of Sthenelus, the son of Perseus, there,
6150	She knew, was sev'n months pregnant of a son;
6150	Whom, though untimely born, she brought to light,
6150	Staying meanwhile Alcmena's labour-pangs,
6150	To Saturn's son herself the tidings brought,
6150	And thus address'd him: 'Jove, the lightning's Lord,
6150	I bring thee news; this day a mighty man,
6150	By thee ordain'd to be the Argives' King,
6150	Is born, Eurystheus, son of Sthenelus,
6150	The son of Perseus, issue of thy blood;
6150	Well worthy he to be the Argives' King.'
6150	She said: keen sorrow deeply pierc'd his soul;
6150	Then Ate by the glossy locks he seiz'd
6150	In mighty wrath; and swore a solemn oath,
6150	That to Olympus and the starry Heav'n
6150	She never should return, who all misleads.
6150	His arm then whirling, from the starry Heav'n
6150	He flung her down, to vex th' affairs of men.
6150	Yet oft her fraud remember'd he with groans,
6150	When by Eurystheus' hard commands he saw
6150	Condemn'd to servile tasks his noble son.
6150	So, oft as Hector of the glancing helm
6150	Beside the ships the Greeks to slaughter gave,
6150	Back to my mind my former error came.
6150	I err'd, for Jove my judgment took away;
6150	But friendly reconcilement now I seek,
6150	And tender costly presents; then thyself
6150	Uprouse thee, and excite the rest to arms.
6150	While I prepare the gifts, whate'er of late                     [6]
6150	The sage Ulysses promis'd in thy tent:
6150	Or, if thou wilt, though eager for the fray,
6150	Remain thou here awhile, till from my ship
6150	My followers bring the gifts; that thou mayst see
6150	"I make my offerings with no niggard hand."""
6150	""
6150	Whom answer'd thus Achilles swift of foot:
6150	"""Most mighty Agamemnon, King of men,"
6150	The gifts thou deem'st befitting, 'tis for thee
6150	To give, or to withhold; but now at once
6150	Prepare we for the battle; 'tis not meet
6150	On trivial pretexts here to waste our time,
6150	Or idly loiter; much remains to do:
6150	Again be seen Achilles in the van,
6150	Scatt'ring with brazen spear the Trojan ranks;
6150	"And ye, forget not man with man to fight."""
6150	""
6150	To whom in answer sage Ulysses thus:
6150	"""Brave as thou art, Achilles, godlike chief,"
6150	Yet fasting lead not forth the sons of Greece
6150	To fight the Trojans; for no little time
6150	Will last the struggle, when the serried ranks
6150	Are once engag'd in conflict, and the Gods
6150	With equal courage either side inspire:
6150	But bid them, by the ships, of food and wine
6150	(Wherein are strength and courage) first partake;
6150	For none throughout the day till set of sun,
6150	Fasting from food, may bear the toils of war;
6150	His spirit may still be eager for the fray;
6150	Yet are his limbs by slow degrees weigh'd down,
6150	Himself by thirst and hunger worn, his knees
6150	Unable, as he moves, to bear his weight.
6150	But he who, first with food and wine refresh'd,
6150	All day maintains the combat with the foe,
6150	His spirit retains unbroken, and his limbs
6150	Unwearied, till both armies quit the field.
6150	Disperse then now the crowd, and bid prepare
6150	The morning meal; meantime to public view
6150	Let Agamemnon, King of men, display
6150	His costly gifts; that all the Greeks may see,
6150	And that thy heart within thee melt with joy:
6150	And there in full assembly let him swear
6150	A solemn oath, that he hath ne'er approach'd
6150	The fair Briseis' bed, nor held with her
6150	Such intercourse as man with woman holds.
6150	Be thou propitious, and accept his oath.
6150	Then at a sumptuous banquet in his tent
6150	Let him receive thee; that thine honour due
6150	May nothing lack; and so, Atrides, thou
6150	Shalt stand in sight of all men clear of blame;
6150	For none can wonder that insulting speech
6150	"Should rouse the anger of a sceptred King."""
6150	""
6150	To whom thus Agamemnon, King of men:
6150	"""Son of Laertes, I accept thy speech"
6150	With cordial welcome: all that thou hast said
6150	Is well and wisely spoken; for the oath,
6150	I am prepar'd, with willing mind, to swear;
6150	Nor in the sight of Heav'n will be forsworn.
6150	Let then Achilles here awhile remain,
6150	Though eager for the fray; ye too remain,
6150	Until the presents from my tent be brought,
6150	And we our solemn compact ratify.
6150	Then this command upon thyself I lay:
6150	That thou the noblest youths of all the Greeks
6150	Select, and bid them from my vessel bear
6150	The gifts, which, to Achilles yesternight
6150	We promis'd, and withal the women bring;
6150	And let Talthybius through the host seek out
6150	"A boar, for sacrifice to Jove and Sol."""
6150	""
6150	Whom answer'd thus Achilles swift of foot:
6150	"""Most mighty Agamemnon, King of men,"
6150	These matters to some future time were best
6150	Deferr'd, some hour of respite from the fight,
6150	Of rage less fiercely burning in my breast;
6150	But slaughter'd now they lie, whom Priam's son,
6150	Hector, hath slain, by Jove to vict'ry led.
6150	Ye bid us take our food; if I might rule,
6150	I would to battle lead the sons of Greece,
6150	Unfed, and fasting; and at set of sun,
6150	Our shame aveng'd, an ample feast prepare;
6150	Till then, nor food nor drink shall pass my lips,
6150	My comrade slain; who pierc'd with mortal wounds,
6150	Turn'd tow'rd the doorway, lies within my tent,
6150	His mourning friends around; while there he lies,
6150	No thought have I for these or aught beside,
6150	"Save carnage, blood, and groans of dying men."""
6150	""
6150	To whom Ulysses, sage in council, thus:
6150	"""O son of Peleus, noblest of the Greeks,"
6150	How far, Achilles, thou surpassest me
6150	In deeds of arms, I know: but thou must yield
6150	To me in counsel, for my years are more,
6150	And my experience greater far than thine:
6150	Then to my words incline a patient ear.
6150	Men soonest weary of battle, where the sword
6150	The bloodiest harvest reaps; the lightest crop
6150	Of slaughter is where Jove inclines the scale,
6150	Dispenser, at his will, of human wars.
6150	The Greeks by fasting cannot mourn their dead;
6150	For day by day successive numbers fall;
6150	Where were the respite then from ceaseless fast?
6150	Behoves us bury out of sight our dead,
6150	Steeling our hearts, and weeping but a day;
6150	And we, the rest, whom cruel war has spar'd,
6150	Should first with food and wine recruit out strength;
6150	Then, girding on our arms, the livelong day
6150	Maintain the war, unwearied; then let none
6150	Require a farther summons to the field;
6150	(And woe to him who loit'ring by the ships
6150	That summons hears;) but with united force
6150	"Against the Trojans wake the furious war."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and call'd on noble Nestor's sons,
6150	On Meges, Phyleus' son, Meriones,
6150	Thoas, and Lycomedes, Creon's son,
6150	And Melanippus; they together sought
6150	The mighty monarch Agamemnon's tent.
6150	Soon as the word was giv'n, the work was done;
6150	Sev'n tripods brought they out, the promis'd gifts;
6150	Twelve horses, twenty caldrons glitt'ring bright;
6150	Sev'n women too, well skill'd in household cares,
6150	With whom, the eighth, the fair Briseis came.
6150	Ulysses led the way, and with him brought
6150	Ten talents full of gold; th' attendant youths
6150	The other presents bore, and in the midst
6150	Display'd before th' assembly: then uprose
6150	The monarch Agamemnon; by his side,
6150	With voice of godlike pow'r, Talthybius stood,
6150	Holding the victim: then Atrides drew
6150	The dagger, ever hanging at his side,
6150	Close by the scabbard of his mighty sword,
6150	And from the victim's head the bristles shore.
6150	With hands uplifted then to Jove he pray'd;
6150	While all around the Greeks in silence stood,
6150	List'ning, decorous, to the monarch's words,
6150	As looking up to Heav'n he made his pray'r:
6150	""
6150	"""Be witness, Jove, thou highest, first of Gods,"
6150	And Sun, and Earth, and ye who vengeance wreak
6150	Beneath the earth on souls of men forsworn,
6150	Furies! that never, or to love unchaste
6150	Soliciting, or otherwise, my hand
6150	Hath fair Briseis touch'd; but in my tent
6150	Still pure and undefil'd hath she remain'd:
6150	And if in this I be forsworn, may Heav'n
6150	With all the plagues afflict me, due to those
6150	"Who sin by perjur'd oaths against the Gods."""
6150	""
6150	Thus as he spoke, across the victim's throat
6150	He drew the pitiless blade; Talthybius then
6150	To hoary Ocean's depths the carcase threw,
6150	Food for the fishes; then Achilles rose,
6150	And thus before th' assembled Greeks he spoke:
6150	""
6150	"""O Father Jove, how dost thou lead astray"
6150	Our human judgments! ne'er had Atreus' son
6150	My bosom fill'd with wrath, nor from my arms,
6150	To his own loss, against my will had torn
6150	The girl I lov'd, but that the will of Jove
6150	To death predestin'd many a valiant Greek.
6150	"Now to the meal; anon renew the war."""
6150	""
6150	This said, th' assembly he dismiss'd in haste,
6150	The crowd dispersing to their sev'ral ships;
6150	Upon the gifts the warlike Myrmidons
6150	Bestow'd their care, and bore them to the ships;
6150	Of Peleus' godlike son; within the tent
6150	They laid them down, and there the women plac'd,
6150	While to the drove the followers led the steeds.
6150	Briseis, fair as golden Venus, saw
6150	Patroclus lying, pierc'd with mortal wounds,
6150	Within the tent; and with a bitter cry,
6150	She flung her down upon the corpse, and tore
6150	Her breast, her delicate neck, and beauteous cheeks;
6150	And, weeping, thus the lovely woman wail'd:
6150	""
6150	"""Patroclus, dearly lov'd of this sad heart!"
6150	When last I left this tent, I left thee full
6150	Of healthy life; returning now, I find
6150	Only thy lifeless corpse, thou Prince of men!
6150	So sorrow still, on sorrow heap'd, I bear.
6150	The husband of my youth, to whom my sire
6150	And honour'd mother gave me, I beheld
6150	Slain with the sword before the city walls:
6150	Three brothers, whom with me one mother bore,
6150	My dearly lov'd ones, all were doom'd to death:
6150	Nor wouldst thou, when Achilles swift of foot
6150	My husband slew, and royal Mynes' town
6150	In ruin laid, allow my tears to flow;
6150	But thou wouldst make me (such was still thy speech)
6150	The wedded wife of Peleus' godlike son:
6150	Thou wouldst to Phthia bear me in thy ship,
6150	And there, thyself, amid the Myrmidons,
6150	Wouldst give my marriage feast; then, unconsol'd,
6150	"I weep thy death, my ever-gentle friend!"""
6150	""
6150	Weeping, she spoke; the women join'd her wail:
6150	Patroclus' death the pretext for their tears,
6150	But each in secret wept her private griefs.
6150	""
6150	Around Achilles throng'd the elder men,
6150	Urging to eat; but he, with groans, refus'd:
6150	"""I pray you, would you show your love, dear friends,"
6150	Ask me not now with food or drink to appease
6150	Hunger or thirst; a load of bitter grief
6150	Weighs heavy on my soul; till set of sun
6150	"Fasting will I remain, and still endure."""
6150	""
6150	The other monarchs at his word withdrew:
6150	The two Atridae, and Ulysses sage,
6150	And Nestor and Idomeneus remain'd,
6150	And aged Phoenix, to divert his grief;
6150	But comfort none, save in the bloody jaws
6150	Of battle would he take; by mem'ry stirr'd,
6150	He heav'd a deep-drawn sigh, as thus he spoke:
6150	""
6150	"""How oft hast thou, ill-fated, dearest friend,"
6150	Here in this tent with eager zeal prepar'd
6150	The tempting meal, whene'er the sons of Greece
6150	In haste would arm them for the bloody fray!
6150	Now liest thou there, while I, for love of thee,
6150	From food and drink, before me plac'd, refrain:
6150	For ne'er shall I again such sorrow know,
6150	Not though I heard of aged Peleus' death,
6150	Who now in Phthia mourns, with tender tears,
6150	His absent son; he on a foreign shore
6150	Is warring in that hateful Helen's cause:
6150	No, nor of his, who now in Scyros' isle
6150	Is growing up, if yet indeed he live,
6150	Young Neoptolemus, my godlike son.
6150	My hope had been indeed, that here in Troy,
6150	Far from the plains of Argos, I alone
6150	Was doom'd to die; and that to Phthia thou,
6150	Return'd in safety, mightst my son convey
6150	From Scyros home, and show him all my wealth,
6150	My spoils, my slaves, my lofty, spacious house.
6150	For Peleus or to death, methinks, e'en now
6150	Hath yielded, or not far from death remov'd,
6150	Lives on in sorrow, bow'd by gloomy age,
6150	Expecting day by day the messenger
6150	"Who bears the mournful tidings of my death."""
6150	""
6150	Weeping, Achilles spoke; and with him wept
6150	The Elders; each to fond remembrance mov'd
6150	Of all that in his home himself had left.
6150	The son of Saturn, pitying, saw their grief,
6150	And Pallas thus with winged words address'd:
6150	"""My child, dost thou a hero's cause forsake,"
6150	Or does Achilles claim no more thy care,
6150	Who sits in sorrow by the high-prow'd ships,
6150	Mourning his comrade slain? the others all
6150	Partake the meal, while he from food abstains:
6150	Then haste thee, and, with hunger lest he faint,
6150	"Drop nectar and ambrosia on his breast."""
6150	""
6150	His words fresh impulse gave to Pallas' zeal:
6150	Down, like the long-wing'd falcon, shrill of voice,
6150	Thro' the clear sky she swoop'd: and while the Greeks
6150	Arm'd for the fight, Achilles she approach'd,
6150	And nectar and ambrosia on his breast
6150	Distill'd, lest hunger should his strength subdue;
6150	Back to her mighty Father's ample house
6150	Returning, as from out the ships they pour'd.
6150	Thick as the snow-flakes that from Heav'n descend,
6150	Before the sky-born Boreas' chilling blast;
6150	So thick, outpouring from the ships, the stream
6150	Of helmets polish'd bright, and bossy shields,
6150	And breastplates firmly brac'd, and ashen spears:
6150	Their brightness flash'd to Heav'n; and laugh'd the Earth
6150	Beneath the brazen glare; loud rang the tramp
6150	Of armed men: Achilles in the midst,
6150	The godlike chief, in dazzling arms array'd.
6150	His teeth were gnashing audibly; his eye
6150	Blaz'd with, the light of fire; but in his heart
6150	Was grief unbearable; with furious wrath
6150	He burn'd against the Trojans, as he donn'd
6150	The heav'nly gifts, the work of Vulcan's hand.
6150	First on his legs the well-wrought greaves he fix'd,
6150	Fasten'd with silver clasps; his breastplate next
6150	Around his chest; and o'er his shoulders flung
6150	His silver-studded sword, with blade of brass;
6150	Then took his vast and weighty shield, whence gleam'd
6150	A light refulgent as the full-orb'd moon;
6150	Or as to seamen o'er the wave is borne
6150	The watchfire's light, which, high among the hills,
6150	Some shepherd kindles in his lonely fold:
6150	As they, reluctant, by the stormy winds,
6150	Far from their friends are o'er the waters driv'n;
6150	So from Achilles' shield, bright, richly wrought,
6150	The light was thrown. The weighty helm he rais'd,
6150	And plac'd it on his head; the plumed helm
6150	Shone like a star; and wav'd the hairs of gold.
6150	Thick-set by Vulcan in the gleaming crest.
6150	Then all the arms Achilles prov'd, to know
6150	If well they fitted to his graceful limbs:
6150	Like wings, they seem'd to lift him from the ground.
6150	Last, from its case he drew his father's spear,
6150	Long, pond'rous, tough; not one of all the Greeks,
6150	None, save Achilles' self, could poise that spear;
6150	The far-fam'd Pelian ash, which to his sire,
6150	On Pelion's summit fell'd, to be the bane
6150	Of mighty chiefs, the Centaur Chiron gave.
6150	With care Automedon and Alcimus
6150	The horses yok'd, with collars fair attach'd:
6150	Plac'd in their mouths the bits, and pass'd the reins
6150	Back to the well-built car: Automedon
6150	Sprang on the car, with shining lash in hand:
6150	Behind, Achilles came, array'd for war,
6150	In arms all glitt'ring as the gorgeous sun,
6150	And loudly to his father's steeds he call'd:
6150	"""Xanthus and Balius, noble progeny"
6150	Of swift Podarge, now in other sort
6150	Back to the Grecian ranks in safety bear,
6150	When he shall quit the field, your charioteer;
6150	"Nor leave him, as ye left Patroclus, slain."""
6150	""
6150	To whom in answer from beneath the yoke
6150	Xanthus, the noble horse, with glancing feet:
6150	Bowing his head the while, till all his mane
6150	Down from th' yokeband streaming, reach'd the ground;
6150	By Juno, white-arm'd Queen, with speech endued:
6150	""
6150	"""Yes, great Achilles, we this day again"
6150	Will bear thee safely; but thy day of doom
6150	Is nigh at hand; nor we shall cause thy death,
6150	But Heav'n's high will, and Fate's imperious pow'r.
6150	By no default of ours, nor lack of speed,
6150	The Trojans stripp'd Patroclus of his arms:
6150	The mighty God, fair-hair'd Latona's son,
6150	Achiev'd his death, and Hector's vict'ry gain'd.
6150	Our speed of foot may vie with Zephyr's breeze,
6150	Deem'd swiftest of the winds; but thou art doom'd
6150	"To die, by force combin'd of God and man."""
6150	""
6150	He said; his farther speech the Furies stay'd.
6150	To whom in wrath Achilles swift of foot;
6150	"""Xanthus, why thus predict my coming fate?"
6150	It ill beseems thee! well I know myself
6150	That I am fated here in Troy to die,
6150	Far from my home and parents; yet withal
6150	I cease not, till these Trojans from the field
6150	"Before me fly."" He said, and to the front,"
6150	His war-cry shouting, urg'd his fiery steeds.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	ARGUMENT.
6150	""
6150	THE BATTLE OF THE GODS, AND THE ACTS OF ACHILLES.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	Jupiter, upon Achilles' return to the battle, calls a council of the
6150	gods and permits them to assist either party. The terrors of the combat
6150	described when the deities are engaged. Apollo encourages AEneas to
6150	meet Achilles. After a long conversation, these two heroes encounter;
6150	but AEneas is preserved by the assistance of Neptune. Achilles falls
6150	upon the rest of the Trojans, and is upon the point of killing Hector,
6150	but Apollo conveys him away in a cloud. Achilles pursues the Trojans
6150	with a great slaughter.
6150	""
6150	The same day continues. The scene is in the field before Troy.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	BOOK XX.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	Round thee, Achilles, eager for the fray,
6150	Stood thus accoutred, by their beaked ships,
6150	The sons of Greece; the Trojan host, oppos'd,
6150	Stood on the sloping margin of the plain.
6150	Then Jove to Themis gave command to call
6150	The Gods to council from the lofty height
6150	Of many-ridg'd Olympus; to the house
6150	Of Jove she summon'd them from ev'ry side.
6150	Thence of the Rivers, save Oceanus,
6150	Not one was absent; nor of Nymphs, who haunt
6150	Clear fount, or shady grove, or grassy mead.
6150	They, at the Cloud-compeller's house arriv'd,
6150	Within the polish'd corridor reclin'd,
6150	Which Vulcan's cunning hand for Jove had built.
6150	There were they gather'd in th' abode of Jove:
6150	Nor did th' Earth-shaking Neptune slight the call,
6150	But came from ocean's depths, and in the midst
6150	He sat, and thus the will of Jove enquir'd:
6150	""
6150	"""Why, Lord of lightning, hast thou summon'd here"
6150	The Gods to council? dost thou aught devise
6150	Touching the Greeks and Trojans? who e'en now
6150	"Kindle anew, it seems, the blaze of war."""
6150	""
6150	To whom the Cloud-compeller, answ'ring, thus:
6150	"""The purpose, Neptune, well thou know'st thyself"
6150	For which I call'd ye; true, they needs must die,
6150	But still they claim my care; yet here will I
6150	Upon Olympus' lofty ridge remain,
6150	And view, serene, the combat; you, the rest,
6150	Go, as you list, to Trojans or to Greeks,
6150	And at your pleasure either party aid.
6150	For if we leave Achilles thus alone
6150	To fight against the Trojans, not an hour
6150	Will they before the son of Peleus stand.
6150	They dreaded him before; but now, I fear,
6150	Since rous'd to fury by his comrade's death,
6150	"He e'en in fate's despite may storm the wall."""
6150	""
6150	Thus Saturn's son, and quenchless battle rous'd:
6150	The Gods, divided, hasten'd to the war:
6150	Juno and Pallas to the ships of Greece,
6150	With them th' Earth-shaker, and the helpful God,
6150	Hermes, for cunning subtleties unmatch'd;
6150	And Vulcan too, exulting in his strength,
6150	Yet halting, and on feeble limbs sustain'd.
6150	Mars of the glancing helm took part with Troy,
6150	And golden Phoebus with his locks unshorn,
6150	Latona too, and Dian, Archer-Queen,
6150	Xanthus, and Venus, laughter-loving dame.
6150	""
6150	While from the fight of men the Gods abstain'd,
6150	High rose the Grecian vaunts, as, long withdrawn,
6150	Achilles on the field again appear'd:
6150	And ev'ry Trojan's limbs with terror quak'd,
6150	Trembling, as Peleus' godlike son they saw,
6150	In arms all-glitt'ring, fierce as blood-stain'd Mars.
6150	But when th' Immortals mingled in the throng,
6150	Then furious wax'd the spirit-stirring strife;
6150	Then Pallas rais'd her war-cry, standing now
6150	Beside the deep-dug trench, without the wall,
6150	Now shouting loud along the sounding beach.
6150	On th' other side, as with the tempest's roar,
6150	Mars to the Trojans shouted loud; one while
6150	From Ilium's topmost height; anon again
6150	From the fair hill, o'erhanging Simois' stream.
6150	Thus, either side exciting to the fray,
6150	Th' immortal Gods unchain'd the angry war.
6150	Thunder'd on high the Sire of Gods and men
6150	With awful din; while Neptune shook beneath
6150	The boundless earth, and lofty mountain tops.
6150	The spring-abounding Ida quak'd and rock'd
6150	From her firm basis to her loftiest peak,
6150	And Troy's proud city, and the ships of Greece.
6150	Pluto, th' infernal monarch, heard alarm'd,
6150	And, springing from his throne, cried out in fear,
6150	Lest Neptune, breaking through the solid earth,
6150	To mortals and Immortals should lay bare
6150	His dark and drear abode, of Gods abhorr'd.
6150	Such was the shock when Gods in battle met;
6150	For there to royal Neptune stood oppos'd
6150	Phoebus Apollo with his arrows keen;
6150	The blue-ey'd Pallas to the God of War;
6150	To Juno, Dian, heav'nly Archeress,
6150	Sister of Phoebus, golden-shafted Queen.
6150	Stout Hermes, helpful God, Latona fac'd;
6150	While Vulcan met the mighty rolling stream,
6150	Xanthus by Gods, by men Scamander call'd.
6150	Thus Gods encounter'd Gods: Achilles' soul
6150	Meantime was burning 'mid the throng to meet
6150	Hector, the son of Priam; with whose blood
6150	He long'd to glut th' insatiate Lord of War.
6150	Apollo then, the spirit-stirring God,
6150	AEneas mov'd Achilles to confront,
6150	And fill'd with courage high; and thus, the voice
6150	Assuming of Lycaon, Priam's son,
6150	Apollo, son of Jove, the chief address'd:
6150	""
6150	"""AEneas, prince and councillor of Troy,"
6150	Where are the vaunts, which o'er the wine-cup late
6150	Thou mad'st amid th' assembled chiefs of Troy,
6150	"That hand to hand thou wouldst Achilles meet?"""
6150	""
6150	To whom AEneas thus in answer spoke:
6150	"""Why, son of Priam, urge me to contend,"
6150	Against my will, with Peleus' mighty son?
6150	Not for the first time should I now engage
6150	Achilles swift of foot: I met him once,
6150	And fled before his spear, on Ida's hill,
6150	When on our herds he fell; Lyrnessus then
6150	He raz'd, and Pedasus; me Jove preserv'd,
6150	With strength, endowing, and with speed of foot.
6150	Else had I fall'n beneath Achilles' hand,
6150	By Pallas aided; who before him moves,
6150	Light of his life, and guides his brazen spear
6150	Trojans and Leleges alike to slay.
6150	'Tis not in mortal man with him to fight,
6150	Whom still some God attends, and guards from harm;
6150	And, e'en unaided, to the mark his spear
6150	Unerring flies, uncheck'd until it pierce
6150	A warrior's breast; yet if the Gods the scale
6150	Impartial held, all brass-clad as he is,
6150	"O'er me no easy triumph should he gain."""
6150	""
6150	To whom the King Apollo, son of Jove:
6150	"""Brave chief, do thou too to th' immortal Gods"
6150	Address thy pray'r; men say that thou art sprung
6150	From Venus, child of Jove; his mother owns
6150	A humbler origin; one born to Jove,
6150	The other to the aged Ocean God.
6150	On then with dauntless spear, nor be dismay'd
6150	"By his high tone and vaunting menaces."""
6150	""
6150	His words with courage fill'd the hero's breast,
6150	And on he sprang, in dazzling arms arrayed;
6150	But not unmark'd of white-arm'd Juno pass'd,
6150	To meet Achilles, through the press of men,
6150	Who thus address'd the Gods, to council call'd:
6150	""
6150	"""Neptune and Pallas both, bethink ye well"
6150	What now should be our course; AEneas comes,
6150	In dazzling arms array'd, to meet in fight
6150	The son of Peleus; Phoebus sends him forth.
6150	Say, then, shall we, encount'ring, to retreat
6150	Perforce constrain him? or shall one of us
6150	Beside Achilles stand, and give him strength
6150	That he may nothing lack; and know himself
6150	By all the mightiest of th' immortal Gods
6150	Belov'd, and those how pow'rless, by whose aid
6150	The Trojans yet maintain defensive war?
6150	Therefore, to join the battle, came we all
6150	From high Olympus, that in this day's fight
6150	No ill befall him; though the time shall come
6150	For him to meet the doom, by fate decreed,
6150	When at his birth his thread of life was spun.
6150	But if Achilles from a voice divine
6150	Receive not this assurance, he may well
6150	Be struck with fear, if haply to some God
6150	He find himself oppos'd: 'tis hard for man
6150	"To meet, in presence visible, a God."""
6150	""
6150	To whom Earth-shaking Neptune thus replied:
6150	"""Juno, thine anger carry not too far;"
6150	It ill beseems thee. Not with my consent
6150	Shall we, the stronger far, provoke to arms
6150	The other Gods; but rather, from the field
6150	Retiring, let us from on high survey,
6150	To mortals left, the turmoil of the war.
6150	Should Mars or Phoebus then begin the fight,
6150	Or stay Achilles, and his arm restrain,
6150	Then in the contest we too may engage;
6150	And soon, methinks, will they be fain to join,
6150	Driv'n from the field, the Synod of the Gods,
6150	"Subdued perforce by our victorious hands."""
6150	""
6150	The dark-hair'd monarch spoke; and led the way
6150	To the high wall, by Trojans built of old,
6150	With Pallas' aid, for godlike Hercules;
6150	Within whose circle he might safety seek,
6150	When from the beach the monster of the deep
6150	Might chase him toward the plain; there Neptune sat,
6150	And with him, the other Gods, a veil of cloud
6150	Impenetrable around their shoulders spread.
6150	On th' other side, upon the fair hill's brow,
6150	Phoebus with Mars the fort-destroyer sat.
6150	On either side they sat, each facing each
6150	With hostile counsels; yet reluctant both
6150	To take th' initiative of ruthless war;
6150	Till Jove, enthron'd on high, the signal gave.
6150	Then all the plain, with men and horses throng'd,
6150	The brazen gleam illumin'd; rang the earth
6150	Beneath their feet, as to the battle-shock
6150	They rush'd; but in the midst, both hosts between,
6150	Eager for fight, stood forth two warriors bold,
6150	Proudly pre-eminent; Anchises' son
6150	AEneas, and Achilles' godlike might.
6150	""
6150	AEneas first with threat'ning mien advanc'd,
6150	Nodding his pond'rous helm; before his breast
6150	His shield he bore, and pois'd his brazen spear.
6150	Him met Achilles from th' opposing ranks;
6150	Fierce as a rav'ning lion, whom to slay
6150	Pour forth the stalwart youths, th' united strength
6150	Of the rous'd village; he unheeding moves
6150	At first; but wounded by a jav'lin thrown
6150	By some bold youth, he turns, with gaping jaws,
6150	And frothing fangs, collecting for the spring,
6150	His breast too narrow for his mighty heart;
6150	And with his tail he lashes both his flanks
6150	And sides, as though to rouse his utmost rage;
6150	Then on, in pride of strength, with glaring eyes
6150	He dashes, if some hunter he may slay,
6150	Or in the foremost rank himself be slain.
6150	So mov'd his dauntless spirit Peleus' son
6150	AEneas to confront; when near they came,
6150	Thus first Achilles, swift of foot, began:
6150	""
6150	"""AEneas, why so far before the ranks"
6150	Advanc'd? dost thou presume with me to fight?
6150	Perchance expecting that the throne of Troy
6150	And Priam's royal honours may be thine.
6150	E'en if thou slay me, deem not to obtain
6150	Such boon from Priam; valiant sons are his,
6150	And he not weak, but bears a constant mind.
6150	Or have the Trojans set apart for thee
6150	Some favour'd spot, the fairest of the land,
6150	Orchard or corn-land, shouldst thou work my death;
6150	Which thou shalt find, I trust, too hard a task?
6150	Already hast thou fled before my spear;
6150	Hast thou forgotten how amid thy herds
6150	Alone I found thee, and with flying foot
6150	Pursued thee down the steep of Ida's hill?
6150	Nor didst thou dare to turn, or pause in flight.
6150	Thou to Lyrnessus fledd'st; Lyrnessus I,
6150	With Pallas' aid and Jove's, assail'd and took:
6150	Their women thence, their days of freedom lost,
6150	I bore away, my captives; thee from death,
6150	Jove and the other Gods defended then;
6150	But will not now bestow, though such thy hope,
6150	Their succour; then I warn thee, while 'tis time,
6150	Ere ill betide thee, to the gen'ral throng
6150	That thou withdraw, nor stand to me oppos'd:
6150	"After th' event may e'en a fool be wise."""
6150	""
6150	To whom in answer thus AEneas spoke:
6150	"""Achilles, think not me, as though a fool,"
6150	To daunt with lofty speech; I too could well
6150	With cutting words, and insult, answer thee.
6150	Each other's race and parents well we know
6150	From tales of ancient days; although by sight
6150	Nor mine to thee, nor thine to me are known.
6150	To noble Peleus thou, 'tis said, wast born
6150	Of Thetis, fair-hair'd daughter of the sea;
6150	Of great Anchises, Heav'n-descended chief,
6150	I boast me sprung, to him by Venus borne.
6150	Of these shall one or other have this day
6150	To mourn their son; since not with empty words
6150	Shall thou and I from mortal combat part.
6150	But if thou farther wouldst enquire, and learn
6150	The race I spring from, not unknown to men,
6150	By Dardanus, of cloud-compelling Jove
6150	Begotten, was Dardania peopled first,
6150	Ere sacred Ilium, populous city of men,
6150	Was founded on the plain; as yet they dwelt
6150	On spring-abounding Ida's lowest spurs.
6150	To Dardanus was Erichthonius born,
6150	Great King, the wealthiest of the sons of men;
6150	For him were pastur'd in the marshy mead,
6150	Rejoicing with their foals, three thousand mares;
6150	Them Boreas, in the pasture where they fed,
6150	Beheld, enamour'd; and amid the herd
6150	In likeness of a coal-black steed appear'd;
6150	Twelve foals, by him conceiving, they produc'd.
6150	These, o'er the teeming corn-fields as they flew,
6150	Skimm'd o'er the standing ears, nor broke the haulm;
6150	And, o'er wide Ocean's bosom as they flew,
6150	Skimm'd o'er the topmost spray of th' hoary sea
6150	Again, to Erichthonius Tros was born,
6150	The King of Troy; three noble sons were his,
6150	Ilus, Assaracus, and Ganymede;
6150	The fairest he of all the sons of men;
6150	Him, for his beauty, bore the Gods away,
6150	To minister as cup-bearer to Jove,
6150	And dwell amid th' Immortals: Ilus next
6150	Begot a noble son, Laomedon;
6150	Tithonus he, and Priam; Clytius,
6150	Lampus and Icetaon, plant of Mars;
6150	Capys, begotten of Assaracus,
6150	Begot Anchises, and Anchises me:
6150	To Priam godlike Hector owes his birth.
6150	Such is my race, and such the blood I boast;
6150	But Jove, at will, to mortals valour gives
6150	Or minishes; for he is Lord of all.
6150	Then cease we now, like babbling fools, to prate
6150	Here in the centre of the coming fight.
6150	Terms of reproach we both might find, whose weight
6150	Would sink a galley of a hundred oars;
6150	For glibly runs the tongue, and can at will
6150	Give utt'rance to discourse in ev'ry vein;
6150	Wide is the range of language; and such words
6150	As one may speak, another may return.
6150	What need that we should insults interchange?
6150	Like women, who some paltry quarrel wage,
6150	Scolding and brawling in the public street,
6150	And in opprobrious terms their anger vent,
6150	Some true, some false; for so their rage suggests.
6150	With words thou shalt not turn me from the field,
6150	Till we have met in arms; then try we now
6150	"Each other's prowess with our brazen spears."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and hurl'd against the mighty shield
6150	His brazen spear; loud rang the weapon's point;
6150	And at arm's length Achilles held the shield
6150	With his broad hand, in fear that through its folds
6150	AEneas' spear would easy passage find;
6150	Blind fool! forgetful that the glorious gifts
6150	Bestow'd by Gods, are not with ease o'ercome,
6150	Nor yield before th' assaults of mortal men.
6150	""
6150	So broke not through AEneas' sturdy spear,
6150	Stay'd by the golden plate, the gift of Heav'n;
6150	Yet through two plates it pass'd, but three remain'd,
6150	For five were in the shield by Vulcan wrought;
6150	Two were of brass, the inner two of tin,
6150	And one of gold, which stay'd the brazen spear.
6150	""
6150	Achilles threw in turn his pond'rous spear.
6150	And struck the circle of AEneas' shield
6150	Near the first rim, where thinnest lay the brass,
6150	And thinnest too th' o'erlying hide; right through
6150	The Pelian shaft was driv'n; wide gap'd the shield.
6150	AEneas crouch'd, in fear, as o'er his head
6150	He held his shield; the eager weapon pass'd
6150	Through both the circles of his ample shield,
6150	And in the ground, behind him, quiv'ring, stood.
6150	Escap'd the pond'rous weapon, sharpest pain
6150	Flashing across his eyes, in fear he stood,
6150	So close the spear had pass'd him; onward then,
6150	Drawing his trenchant blade, Achilles rush'd,
6150	With fearful shout; a rocky fragment then
6150	AEneas lifted up, a mighty mass,
6150	Which scarce two men, as men are now, could bear,
6150	But he, unaided, lifted it with ease.
6150	Then had AEneas, with the massive stone,
6150	Or on the helmet, or the shield, his death
6150	Averting, struck Achilles; and himself
6150	Had by the sword of Peleus' son been slain,
6150	Had not th' Earth-shaking God his peril seen,
6150	And to th' Immortals thus address'd his speech:
6150	"""Oh, woe is me for great AEneas' sake,"
6150	Who, by Achilles slain, must visit soon
6150	The viewless shades; insensate, who relied
6150	On Phoebus' words; yet nought shall he avail
6150	From death to save him. Yet oh why should he,
6150	Blameless himself, the guilt of others rue?
6150	Who still his grateful sacrifice hath paid
6150	To all the Gods in wide-spread Heav'n who dwell.
6150	Let us then interpose to guard his life;
6150	Lest, if Achilles slay him, Saturn's son
6150	Be mov'd to anger; for his destiny
6150	Would have him live; lest, heirless, from the earth
6150	Should perish quite the race of Dardanus;
6150	By Saturn's son the best-belov'd of all
6150	His sons, to him by mortal women born.
6150	For Jove the race of Priam hath abhorr'd;
6150	But o'er the Trojans shall AEneas reign,
6150	"And his sons' sons, through ages yet unborn."""
6150	""
6150	Whom answer'd thus the stag-ey'd Queen of Heav'n:
6150	"""Neptune, do thou determine for thyself"
6150	AEneas to withdraw, or leave to fall,
6150	Good as he is, beneath Achilles' sword;
6150	But we before th' immortal Gods are bound,
6150	Both I and Pallas, by repeated oaths,
6150	Ne'er from his doom one Trojan life to save,
6150	Though to devouring flames a prey, all Troy
6150	"Were blazing, kindled by the valiant Greeks."""
6150	""
6150	Th' Earth-shaker heard; and thro' the fight he pass'd,
6150	And through the throng of spears, until he came
6150	Where great Achilles and AEneas stood.
6150	Around the eyes of Peleus' son he spread
6150	A veil of mist; then from AEneas' shield
6150	The brass-tipp'd spear withdrawing, laid it down
6150	Before Achilles' feet; and lifting up
6150	AEneas, bore him high above the ground.
6150	O'er many a rank of warriors and of cars
6150	AEneas flew, supported by the God;
6150	Till to the field's extremest verge he came,
6150	Where stood the Caucons, arming for the war.
6150	There to AEneas, standing by his side,
6150	Th' Earth-shaker thus his winged words address'd:
6150	"""AEneas, say what God has mov'd thee thus"
6150	Against Achilles, reckless, to contend,
6150	Thy stronger far, and dearer to the Gods?
6150	If e'er he cross thy path, do thou retire,
6150	Lest, e'en despite of fate, thou find thy death.
6150	But when Achilles hath to fate succumb'd,
6150	Then, fearless, with the foremost join the fray:
6150	"No other Greek shall bear away thy spoils."""
6150	""
6150	Thus plainly warn'd, AEneas there he left.
6150	Then from Achilles' eyes he purg'd the film:
6150	Astonish'd, he with eyes wide open gaz'd,
6150	As thus he commun'd with his mighty heart:
6150	""
6150	"""O Heav'n, what marvel do mine eyes behold?"
6150	My spear before me laid, and vanish'd he
6150	At whom I hurl'd it with intent to slay!
6150	Then is AEneas of th' immortal Gods
6150	In truth belov'd, though vain I deem'd his boast.
6150	A curse go with him! yet methinks not soon
6150	Will he again presume to prove my might,
6150	Who gladly now in flight escapes from death.
6150	Then, to the valiant Greeks my orders giv'n.
6150	"Let me some other Trojan's mettle prove."""
6150	Then tow'rd the ranks he sprang, each sev'ral man
6150	"Exhorting: ""From the Trojans, valiant Greeks,"
6150	No longer stand aloof; but man to man
6150	Confront the foe, and nobly dare the fight.
6150	'Twere hard for me, brave warrior though I be,
6150	To face such numbers, and to fight with all:
6150	Not Mars, nor Pallas, though immortal Gods,
6150	Could face, and vanquish, such a mighty mass.
6150	But what my single arm, and feet, and strength
6150	May profit, not a jot will I relax;
6150	Right through the ranks I mean to force my way;
6150	And small shall be that Trojan's cause for joy,
6150	"Who comes within the compass of my spear."""
6150	""
6150	Thus he, exhorting; Hector cheering on
6150	Meanwhile the Trojans, with assurance giv'n
6150	That he himself Achilles would confront.
6150	""
6150	"""Ye valiant Trojans, fear not Peleus' son;"
6150	I too in words could with the Gods contend,
6150	Though not in arms; so much the stronger they.
6150	Not all his words Achilles shall make good;
6150	Fulfilling some, in others he shall fail,
6150	His course midway arrested. Him will I
6150	Encounter, though his hands were hands of fire,
6150	"Of fire his hands, his strength as burnish'd steel."""
6150	""
6150	Thus he, exhorting; with uplifted spears
6150	Advanc'd the Trojans; from the mingling hosts
6150	Loud rose the clamour; then at Hector's side
6150	Apollo stood, and thus address'd the chief:
6150	"""Hector, forbear Achilles to defy;"
6150	And 'mid the crowd withdraw thee from the fray;
6150	Lest with the spear he slay thee, thrown from far,
6150	"Or with the sword in combat hand to hand."""
6150	""
6150	He said; and troubled by the heav'nly voice,
6150	Hector amid the throng of men withdrew.
6150	""
6150	Then, girt with might, amid the Trojans sprang,
6150	With fearful shouts, Achilles; first he slew
6150	Otryntes' son, Iphition, valiant chief
6150	Of num'rous warriors; him a Naiad nymph,
6150	In Hyde's fertile vale, beneath the feet
6150	Of snow-clad Tmolus, to Otryntes bore;
6150	At him, as on he rush'd, Achilles hurl'd,
6150	And through his forehead drove his glitt'ring spear;
6150	The head was cleft in twain; thund'ring he fell,
6150	And o'er him thus Achilles made his boast:
6150	""
6150	"""Son of Otryntes, lie thou there, of men"
6150	The most vain-glorious; here thou find'st thy death,
6150	Far from thy place of birth, beside the lake
6150	Gygaean; there hadst thou thine heritage
6150	Of old, beside the fish-abounding stream
6150	"Of Hyllus, and by Hermus' eddying flood."""
6150	""
6150	Thus he, exulting: o'er Iphition's eyes
6150	Were spread the shades of death; his mangled corpse
6150	Was crush'd beneath the Grecian chariot wheels,
6150	In the first shock. Demoleon next he smote,
6150	A helpful aid in war, Antenor's son,
6150	Pierc'd thro' the temples, thro' the brass-bound helm;
6150	Nor check'd the brazen helm the spear, whose point
6150	Went crashing through the bone, that all the brain
6150	Was shatter'd; onward as he rush'd, he fell.
6150	Then through the neck Hippodamas he smote,
6150	Flying before him, mounted on his car.
6150	Deep groan'd he, breathing out his soul, as groans
6150	A bull, by sturdy youths to th' altar dragg'd
6150	Of Neptune, King divine of Helice;
6150	Th' Earth-shaking God, well pleas'd, the gift receives;
6150	E'en with such groans his noble spirit fled.
6150	The godlike Polydore he next assail'd,
6150	The son of Priam; him his aged sire
6150	Would fain have kept at home, of all his sons
6150	At once the youngest and the best-belov'd;
6150	Among them all for speed of foot unmatch'd;
6150	Whose youthful folly, in the foremost ranks
6150	His speed displaying, cost him now his life.
6150	Him, as he darted by, Achilles' spear
6150	Struck through the centre of the back, where met
6150	The golden clasps that held the glitt'ring belt,
6150	And where the breastplate form'd a double guard:
6150	Right through his body pass'd the weapon's point;
6150	Groaning, he fell upon his knees; dark clouds
6150	O'erspread his eyes; supporting with his hand
6150	His wounded bowels, on the ground he writh'd.
6150	When Hector saw his brother Polydore
6150	Writhing in death, a mist o'erspread his eyes
6150	Nor longer could he bear to stand aloof,
6150	But sprang to meet Achilles, flashing fire,
6150	His keen spear brandishing; at sight of him
6150	Up leap'd Achilles, and exulting cried:
6150	""
6150	"""Lo, here the man who most hath wrung my soul,"
6150	Who slew my lov'd companion: now, methinks,
6150	Upon the pass of war not long shall we
6150	"Stand separate, nor each the other shun."""
6150	""
6150	Then, with stern glance, to godlike Hector thus:
6150	"""Draw near, and quickly meet thy doom of death."""
6150	""
6150	To whom thus Hector of the glancing helm,
6150	"Unterrified: ""Achilles, think not me,"
6150	As though a fool and ignorant of war,
6150	To daunt with lofty speech; I too could well
6150	With cutting words and insult answer thee.
6150	I know thee strong and valiant; and I know
6150	Myself to thee inferior; but th' event
6150	Is with the Gods; and I, if such their will,
6150	The weaker, with my spear may reach thy life:
6150	"My point too hath, ere now, its sharpness prov'd."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and, poising, hurl'd his pond'rous spear,
6150	Which from Achilles Pallas turn'd aside
6150	With lightest breath; and back to Hector sent,
6150	And laid before his feet; intent to slay,
6150	Onward Achilles rush'd, with fearful shout;
6150	But Phoebus Hector from the field convey'd,
6150	(As Gods can only,) veil'd in thickest cloud.
6150	Thrice Peleus' godlike son, with brazen spear,
6150	His onset made; thrice struck the misty cloud;
6150	But when, with pow'r as of a God, he made
6150	His fourth essay, in fury thus he cried:
6150	""
6150	"""Yet once again, vile hound, hast thou escap'd;"
6150	Thy doom was nigh, but thee thy God hath sav'd,
6150	Phoebus, to whom, amid the clash of spears,
6150	Well mayst thou pray! We yet shall meet again;
6150	When I shall end thee, if a guardian God
6150	I too may claim; meanwhile, from thee I turn,
6150	"And others seek on whom my hap may light."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and drove through Dryops' neck his spear,
6150	And stretch'd him at his feet, and pass'd him by.
6150	Next with his spear he struck below the knee
6150	Philetor's son, Demuchus, stout and tall,
6150	And check'd his forward course; then rushing on
6150	Dealt with his mighty sword the mortal blow.
6150	The sons of Bias next, Laogonus
6150	And Dardanus, he hurl'd from off their car,
6150	One with the spear, and one by sword-stroke slain.
6150	Tros too he slew, Alastor's son, who came
6150	To meet him, and embrace his knees, and pray
6150	To spare his life, in pity of his youth:
6150	Little he knew how vain would be his pray'r;
6150	For not of temper soft, nor mild of mood
6150	Was he, but sternly fierce; and as he knelt
6150	And clasp'd his knees, and would his pray'r prefer,
6150	Achilles clove him with his mighty sword,
6150	Gash'd through the liver; as from out the wound
6150	His liver dropp'd, the dark blood gushing forth
6150	His bosom fill'd, and darkness clos'd his eyes,
6150	As ebb'd his life away. Then through the ear
6150	Mulius he thrust; at th' other ear came forth
6150	The brazen point. Echeclus next he met,
6150	Son of Agenor, and his hilted sword
6150	Full on the centre of his head let fall.
6150	The hot blood dy'd the blade; the darkling shades
6150	Of death, and rig'rous fate, his eyes o'erspread.
6150	Next, where the tendons bind the elbow-joint,
6150	The brazen spear transfix'd Deucalion's arm;
6150	With death in prospect, and disabled arm
6150	He stood, till on his neck Achilles' sword
6150	Descending, shar'd, and flung afar, both head
6150	And helmet; from the spine's dissever'd joints
6150	The marrow flow'd, as stretch'd in dust he lay.
6150	The noble son of Peireus next he slew,
6150	Rigmus, who came from Thracia's fertile plains;
6150	Him through the waist he struck, the brazen spear
6150	Plung'd in his bowels; from the car he fell;
6150	And as Areithous, his charioteer,
6150	His horses turn'd, Achilles through the neck
6150	His sharp spear thrusting, hurl'd him to the ground,
6150	The startled steeds in wild confusion thrown.
6150	""
6150	As rage the fires amid the wooded glen
6150	Of some parch'd mountain's side, and fiercely burns
6150	The copse-wood dry, while eddying here and there
6150	The flames are whirl'd before the gusty wind;
6150	So fierce Achilles raged, on ev'ry side
6150	Pursuing, slaught'ring; reek'd the earth with blood.
6150	As when upon a well-roll'd threshing-floor,
6150	Two sturdy-fronted steers, together yok'd,
6150	Tread the white barley out; beneath their feet
6150	Fast flies the grain out-trodden from the husk;
6150	So by Achilles driv'n, his flying steeds
6150	His chariot bore, o'er bodies of the slain
6150	And broken bucklers trampling; all beneath
6150	Was plash'd with blood the axle, and the rails
6150	Around the car, as from the horses' feet
6150	And from the felloes of the wheels were thrown
6150	The bloody gouts; and onward still he press'd,
6150	Panting for added triumphs, deeply dyed
6150	With gore and carnage his unconquer'd hands.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	ARGUMENT.
6150	""
6150	THE BATTLE IN THE RIVER SCAMANDER.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	The Trojans fly before Achilles, some towards the town, others to the
6150	river Scamander; he falls upon the latter with great slaughter, takes
6150	twelve captives alive, to sacrifice to the shade of Patroclus; and
6150	kills Lycaon and Asteropaeus. Scamander attacks him with all his waves;
6150	Neptune and Pallas assist the hero; Simois joins Scamander; at length
6150	Vulcan, by the instigation of Juno, almost dries up the river. This
6150	combat ended, the other gods engage each other. Meanwhile Achilles
6150	continues the slaughter, and drives the rest into Troy; Agenor only
6150	makes a stand, and is conveyed away in a cloud by Apollo: who (to
6150	delude Achilles) takes upon him Agenor's shape, and while he pursues
6150	him in that disguise, gives the Trojans an opportunity of retiring into
6150	their city.
6150	""
6150	The same day continues. The scene is on the banks and in the stream of
6150	Scamander.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	BOOK XXI.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	But when they came to eddying Xanthus' ford,
6150	Fair-flowing stream, born of immortal Jove,
6150	Achilles cut in twain the flying host;
6150	Part driving tow'rd the city, o'er the plain,
6150	Where on the former day the routed Greeks,
6150	When Hector rag'd victorious, fled amain.
6150	On, terror-struck, they rush'd; but Juno spread,
6150	To baffle their retreat, before their path,
6150	Clouds and thick darkness: half the fugitives
6150	In the deep river's silv'ry eddies plung'd:
6150	With clamour loud they fell: the torrent roar'd;
6150	The banks around re-echoed; here and there,
6150	They, with the eddies wildly struggling, swam.
6150	As when, pursued by fire, a hov'ring swarm
6150	Of locusts riverward direct their flight,
6150	And, as th' insatiate flames advance, they cow'r
6150	Amid the waters; so a mingled mass
6150	Of men and horses, by Achilles driv'n,
6150	The deeply-whirling stream, of Xanthus chok'd.
6150	His spear amid the tamarisks on the bank
6150	The hero left; on savage deeds intent,
6150	Arm'd with his sword alone, a God in pow'r,
6150	He sprang amid the torrent; right and left
6150	He smote; then fearful rose the groans of men
6150	Slain with the sword; the stream ran red with blood.
6150	As fishes, flying from a dolphin, crowd
6150	The shoal recesses of some open bay,
6150	In fear, for whom he catches he devours;
6150	So crouch'd the Trojans in the mighty stream
6150	Beneath the banks; and when at length his hand
6150	Wearied of slaughter, from the stream, alive,
6150	He dragg'd twelve youths, whose forfeit lives should be
6150	The bloody fine for slain Patroclus paid.
6150	Helpless from fear, as fawns, he brought them forth;
6150	Their hands secur'd behind them with the belts
6150	Which o'er their shirts of twisted mail they wore,
6150	And bade his comrades lead them to the ships.
6150	Then on again he dash'd, athirst for blood;
6150	And first encounter'd, flying from the stream,
6150	Lycaon, Priam's son; him once before
6150	He by a nightly onslaught had surpris'd,
6150	And from his father's vineyard captive borne:
6150	Where, as he cut, to form his chariot rail,
6150	A fig-tree's tender shoots, unlook'd-for ill
6150	O'ertook him in the form of Peleus' son.
6150	Thence in his ship to Lemnos' thriving isle
6150	He bore him, ransom'd there by Jason's son.
6150	His Imbrian host, Eetion, set him free
6150	With lib'ral gifts, and to Arisba sent:
6150	Escaping thence, he reach'd his native home.
6150	Twelve days save one, rejoicing, with his friends
6150	He spent, return'd from Lemnos: fate, the twelfth,
6150	Again consign'd him to Achilles' hands,
6150	From him, reluctant, to receive his death.
6150	Him when Achilles, swift of foot, beheld,
6150	No spear in hand, of helm and shield bereft,
6150	All flung in haste away, as from the stream,
6150	Reeking with sweat, and faint with toil, he fled,
6150	He commun'd, wrathful, with his mighty heart:
6150	""
6150	"""Ye Gods, what marvel do mine eyes behold!"
6150	Methinks the valiant Trojans slain by me
6150	Ere long will from the realms of darkness rise;
6150	Since, death escaping, but to slav'ry sold
6150	In Lemnos' isle, this fellow hath return'd,
6150	Despite the hoary sea's impediment,
6150	Which many a man against his will hath stay'd:
6150	Now shall he taste my spear, that I may see
6150	If thence too he return, or if the earth
6150	"May keep him safe, which e'en the strongest holds."""
6150	""
6150	Thus, as he stood, he mus'd; but all aghast
6150	Approach'd Lycaon; and would fain have clasp'd
6150	The Hero's knees; for longingly he sought
6150	Escape from bitter death and evil fate.
6150	Achilles rais'd his spear, in act to strike;
6150	He, stooping, ran beneath, and clasp'd his knees;
6150	Above his back the murd'rous weapon pass'd,
6150	And in the earth was fix'd: one suppliant hand
6150	Achilles' knees embrac'd; the other held,
6150	With unrelaxing grasp, the pointed spear;
6150	As he with winged words, imploring, spoke:
6150	""
6150	"""I clasp thy knees, Achilles! look then down"
6150	With pity on my woes; and recognize,
6150	Illustrious chief, a suppliant's sacred claim:
6150	For in thy tent I first broke bread, that day,
6150	When, in my father's fruitful vineyard seiz'd,
6150	Thy captive I became, to slav'ry sold,
6150	Far from my sire and friends, in Lemnos' isle.
6150	A hundred oxen were my ransom then;
6150	At thrice so much I now would buy my life.
6150	This day is but the twelfth, since, sorely tried
6150	By lengthen'd suffering, back to Troy I came.
6150	Now to thy hands once more my cruel fate
6150	Consigns me; surely by the wrath of Jove
6150	Pursued, who gives me to thy pow'r again.
6150	Me, doom'd to early death, my mother bore,
6150	Old Altes' daughter, fair Laothoe;
6150	Altes, who rul'd the warlike Leleges,
6150	In lofty Pedasus, by Satnois' stream.
6150	His child of Priam's many wives was one;
6150	Two sons she bore, and both by thee must die.
6150	Already one, the godlike Polydore,
6150	Amid the foremost ranks thy spear hath slain;
6150	And now my doom hath found me; for from thee,
6150	Since evil fate hath plac'd me in thy hands,
6150	I may not hope to fly; yet hear but this,
6150	And weigh it in thy mind, to spare my life:
6150	I come not of that womb which Hector bore,
6150	"Who slew thy comrade, gentle, kind, and brave."""
6150	""
6150	Thus Priam's noble son, imploring, spoke;
6150	But stern the answer fell upon his ear:
6150	""
6150	"""Thou fool! no more to me of ransom prate!"
6150	Before Patroclus met the doom of death,
6150	To spare the Trojans still my soul inclin'd;
6150	And many captives, ta'en alive, I sold;
6150	But from henceforth, before the walls of Troy,
6150	Not one of all the Trojans, whom the Gods
6150	May to my hands deliver, least of all
6150	A son of Priam, shall escape the death.
6150	Thou too, my friend, must die: why vainly wail?
6150	Dead is Patroclus too, thy better far.
6150	Me too thou see'st, how stalwart, tall, and fair,
6150	Of noble sire, and Goddess-mother born:
6150	Yet must I yield to death and stubborn fate,
6150	Whene'er, at morn, or noon, or eve, the spear
6150	"Or arrow from the bow may reach my life."""
6150	""
6150	He said; and sank Lycaon's limbs and heart;
6150	He loos'd the spear, and sat, with both his hands
6150	Uprais'd, imploring; but Achilles drew,
6150	And on his neck beside the collar-bone
6150	Let fall his trenchant sword; the two-edg'd blade
6150	Was buried deep; prone on the earth he lay;
6150	Forth gush'd the crimson blood, and dyed the ground.
6150	""
6150	Him, dragging by the feet, Achilles threw
6150	In the mid stream, and thus with vaunting speech:
6150	""
6150	"""Lie there amid the fishes, who shall cleanse,"
6150	But not with kindly thought, thy gory wounds:
6150	O'er thee, extended on thy bier, shall rise
6150	No mother's wail; Scamander's eddying stream
6150	Shall to the sea's broad bosom roll thee down;
6150	And, springing through the darkly rippling wave,
6150	Fishes shall rise, and banquet on thy flesh.
6150	On now the work of death! till, flying ye,
6150	And slaught'ring I, we reach the city wall.
6150	Nor this fair-flowing, silver-eddying stream,
6150	Shall aught avail ye, though to him ye pay
6150	In sacrifice the blood of countless bulls,
6150	And living horses in his waters sink.
6150	Ye all shall perish, till Patroclus' death
6150	Be fully aveng'd, and slaughter of the Greeks,
6150	"Whom, in my absence, by the ships ye slew."""
6150	He said: the mighty River at his words
6150	Indignant chaf'd, and ponder'd in his mind
6150	How best to check Achilles' warlike toil,
6150	And from destruction guard the Trojan host.
6150	""
6150	Meantime Achilles with his pond'rous spear
6150	Asteropaeus, son of Pelegon,
6150	Assail'd with deadly purpose; Pelegon
6150	To broadly-flowing Axius ow'd his birth,
6150	The River-God commingling with the blood
6150	Of Periboea, daughter eldest born
6150	Of Acessamenus: on him he sprang;
6150	He, from the river rising, stood oppos'd.
6150	Two lances in his hand; his courage rous'd
6150	By Xanthus, who, indignant, saw his stream
6150	Polluted by the blood of slaughter'd youths,
6150	By fierce Achilles' hand, unpitying, slain.
6150	When near the warriors, each to other, came,
6150	Achilles, swift of foot, took up the word:
6150	"""What man, and whence art thou, who dar'st to stand"
6150	Oppos'd to me? of most unhappy sires
6150	"The children they, who my encounter meet!"""
6150	""
6150	To whom th' illustrious son of Pelegon:
6150	"""Great son of Peleus, why enquire my race?"
6150	From far Paeonia's fertile fields I come,
6150	The leader of the long-spear'd Paeon host.
6150	Ten days have pass'd since I to Ilium came.
6150	From widely-flowing Axius my descent,
6150	Axius, the purest stream on earth that flows.
6150	He Pelegon begot, the spear-renown'd;
6150	Of Pelegon I boast me sprung; and now
6150	"Address thee, brave Achilles, to the fight."""
6150	""
6150	Threat'ning he spoke: Achilles rais'd on high
6150	The Pelian spear; but, ambidexter, he
6150	From either hand at once a jav'lin launch'd.
6150	One struck, but pierc'd not through, the mighty shield,
6150	Stay'd by the golden plate, the gift of Heav'n;
6150	Achilles' right fore-arm the other graz'd:
6150	Forth gush'd the crimson blood; but, glancing by
6150	And vainly longing for the taste of flesh,
6150	The point behind him in the earth was fix'd.
6150	Then at Asteropaeus in his turn
6150	With deadly intent the son of Peleus threw
6150	His straight-directed spear; his mark he miss'd,
6150	But struck the lofty bank, where, deep infix'd
6150	To half its length, the Pelian ash remain'd.
6150	Then from beside his thigh Achilles drew
6150	His trenchant blade, and, furious, onward rush'd;
6150	While from the cliff Asteropaeus strove
6150	In vain, with stalwart hand, to wrench the spear.
6150	Three times he shook it with impetuous force,
6150	Three times relax'd his grasp; a fourth attempt
6150	He made to bend and break the sturdy shaft;
6150	But him, preventing, Peleus' godlike son
6150	With deadly stroke across the belly smote,
6150	And gush'd his bowels forth; upon the ground
6150	Gasping he lay, and darkness seal'd his eyes.
6150	Then on his breast Achilles sprang, and stripp'd
6150	His armour off, and thus with vaunting speech:
6150	"""So lie thou there! 'tis hard for thee to fight,"
6150	Though river-born, against the progeny
6150	Of mighty Jove; a widely-flowing stream
6150	Thou claim'st as author of thy parentage;
6150	My high descent from Jove himself I boast.
6150	My father Peleus, son of AEacus,
6150	Reigns o'er the num'rous race of Myrmidons;
6150	The son of Jove himself was AEacus.
6150	High o'er all rivers, that to th' ocean flow,
6150	Is Jove exalted; and in like degree
6150	Superior is his race in pow'r to theirs.
6150	A mighty River hast thou here at hand,
6150	If that might aught avail thee; but his pow'r
6150	Is impotent to strive with Saturn's son.
6150	With him, not Achelous, King of streams,
6150	Presumes to vie; nor e'en the mighty strength
6150	Of deeply-flowing, wide Oceanus;
6150	From whom all rivers, all the boundless sea,
6150	All fountains, all deep wells derive their source;
6150	Yet him appals the lightning bolt of Jove,
6150	"And thunder, pealing from the vault of Heav'n."""
6150	He said, and from the cliff withdrew his spear.
6150	Him left he lifeless there upon the sand
6150	Extended; o'er him the dark waters wash'd,
6150	And eels and fishes, thronging, gnaw'd his flesh.
6150	Then 'mid the Paeons' plumed host he rush'd,
6150	Who fled along the eddying stream, when him,
6150	Their bravest in the stubborn fight, they saw
6150	Slain by the sword and arm of Peleus' son.
6150	Thersilochus and Mydon then he slew,
6150	Mnesus and Thrasius and Astypylus,
6150	AEnius and Ophelestes; and yet more
6150	Had been the slaughter by Achilles wrought,
6150	But from his eddying depths, in human form,
6150	With wrathful tone the mighty River spoke:
6150	""
6150	"""In strength, Achilles, and in deeds of arms,"
6150	All mortals thou surpassest; for the Gods
6150	Themselves attend thee, and protect from harm;
6150	If Saturn's son have given thee utterly
6150	The Trojans to destroy, yet, ere thou slay,
6150	Far from my waters drive them o'er the plain;
6150	For now my lovely stream is fill'd with dead;
6150	Nor can I pour my current to the sea,
6150	With floating corpses chok'd, whilst thou pursuest
6150	The work of death, insatiate: stay thy hand!
6150	"With horror I behold thee, mighty chief!"""
6150	""
6150	Whom answer'd thus Achilles, swift of foot:
6150	"""Be it as thou wilt, Scamander, Heav'n-born stream;"
6150	Yet cease I not to slay until I drive
6150	These vaunting Trojans to their walls, and prove
6150	The force of Hector, if, in single fight,
6150	"I be by him, or he by me, subdued."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and fiercely on the Trojans rush'd,
6150	A God in might! to Phoebus then his speech
6150	The deeply-eddying River thus address'd:
6150	""
6150	"""God of the silver bow, great son of Jove,"
6150	Obey'st thou thus the will of Saturn's son,
6150	Who charg'd thee by the Trojans still to stand,
6150	And aid their cause, till ev'ning's late approach
6150	"Should cast its shadows o'er the fertile earth?"""
6150	""
6150	Thus as he spoke, from off the lofty bank
6150	Achilles springing in mid current plung'd;
6150	Then high the swelling stream, tumultuous, rose
6150	In all its angry flood; and with a roar
6150	As of a bellowing bull, cast forth to land
6150	The num'rous corpses by Achilles slain;
6150	And many living, in his cavern'd bed,
6150	Conceal'd behind the whirling waters sav'd.
6150	Fierce, round Achilles, rose the boiling wave,
6150	And on his shield descending, drove him down;
6150	Nor might he keep his foothold; but he grasp'd
6150	A lofty elm, well-grown, which from the cliff
6150	Uprooted, all the bank had torn away,
6150	And with its tangled branches check'd the flow
6150	Of the fair river, which with all its length
6150	It bridg'd across; then, springing from the deep,
6150	Swiftly he fled in terror o'er the plain.
6150	Nor ceas'd the mighty River, but pursued,
6150	With darkly-ruffling crest, intent to stay
6150	Achilles' course, and save the Trojan host.
6150	Far as a jav'lin's flight he rush'd, in speed
6150	Like the dark hunter eagle, strongest deem'd,
6150	And swiftest wing'd of all the feather'd race.
6150	So on he sped; loud rattled on his breast
6150	His brazen armour, as before the God,
6150	Cow'ring, he fled; the God behind him still
6150	With thund'ring sound pursued. As when a man
6150	From some dark-water'd spring through trenches leads,
6150	'Mid plants and gardens, th' irrigating stream,
6150	And, spade in hand, th' appointed channel clears:
6150	Down flows the stream anon, its pebbly bed
6150	Disturbing; fast it flows with bubbling sound,
6150	Down the steep slope, o'ertaking him who leads.
6150	Achilles so th' advancing wave o'ertook,
6150	Though great his speed; but man must yield to Gods,
6150	Oft as Achilles, swift of foot, essay'd
6150	To turn and stand, and know if all the Gods,
6150	Who dwell in Heav'n, were leagued to daunt his soul
6150	So oft the Heav'n-born River's mighty wave
6150	Above his shoulders dash'd; in deep distress
6150	He sprang on high; then rush'd the flood below,
6150	And bore him off his legs, and wore away
6150	The soil beneath his feet; then, groaning, thus,
6150	As up to Heav'n he look'd, Achilles cried:
6150	"""O Father Jove, will none of all the Gods"
6150	In pity save me from this angry flood?
6150	Content, thereafter, would I meet my fate.
6150	Of all the pow'rs of Heav'n, my mother most
6150	Hath wrong'd me, who hath buoy'd me up with hope
6150	Delusive, that, before the walls of Troy,
6150	I should by Phoebus' swift-wing'd arrows fall.
6150	Would that by Hector's hand 'twere mine to die,
6150	The bravest of their brave! a warrior so
6150	Were by a warrior slain! now am I doom'd
6150	Ignobly here to sink, the mighty flood
6150	O'erwhelming me, like some poor shepherd lad,
6150	"Borne down in crossing by a wintry brook."""
6150	""
6150	He said; and quickly, cloth'd in mortal form,
6150	Neptune and Pallas at his side appear'd;
6150	With cheering words they took him by the hand,
6150	And thus th' Earth-shaking God his speech began:
6150	""
6150	"""Achilles, fear not thou, nor be dismay'd;"
6150	Such pow'rful aid, by Jove's consent, we bring,
6150	Pallas and I, from Heav'n; 'tis not decreed
6150	That thou shouldst by the River be o'erwhelm'd;
6150	He shall retire ere long, and thou shalt see;
6150	And more, if thou wilt hear, we undertake
6150	That from the war thine arm shall not be stay'd,
6150	Till thou shalt drive beneath the walls of Troy
6150	The crowd of flying Trojans; thou thyself
6150	Shalt Hector slay, and safe regain the ships:
6150	"Such high renown we give thee to achieve."""
6150	""
6150	They to the other Gods, this said, return'd;
6150	He, greatly strengthen'd by the voice divine,
6150	Press'd onwards to the plain; the plain he found
6150	All flooded o'er; and, floating, armour fair,
6150	And many a corpse of men in battle slain;
6150	Yet onward, lifting high his feet, he press'd
6150	Right tow'rd the stream; nor could the mighty stream
6150	Check his advance, such vigour Pallas gave;
6150	Nor did Scamander yet his fury stay,
6150	But fiercer rose his rage; and rearing high
6150	His crested wave, to Simois thus he cried:
6150	""
6150	"""Dear brother, aid me with united force"
6150	This mortal's course to check; he, unrestrain'd,
6150	Will royal Priam's city soon destroy,
6150	Nor will the Trojans his assault endure.
6150	Haste to the rescue then, and from their source
6150	Fill all thy stream, and all thy channels swell;
6150	Rouse thy big waves, and roll a torrent down
6150	Of logs and stones, to whelm this man of might,
6150	Who triumphs now, and bears him as a God.
6150	Nought shall his strength or beauty then avail,
6150	Or gallant arms, beneath the waters sunk,
6150	Deep buried in the mud: himself will I
6150	In sand imbed, and o'er his corpse a pile
6150	Of shingly gravel heap; nor shall the Greeks
6150	Be able to collect his bones, encas'd
6150	By me so deep in slime. His monument
6150	They here may raise; but when they celebrate
6150	"His fun'ral rites, no mound will he require."""
6150	""
6150	He said; and on Achilles, from on high
6150	Came boiling, rushing down, with thund'ring roar,
6150	With foam and blood and corpses intermix'd.
6150	High rose the Heav'n-born River's darkling wave,
6150	And bore Achilles downward; then in fear
6150	Lest the broad waters of the eddying stream
6150	Should quite o'erwhelm him, Juno cried aloud,
6150	And Vulcan thus, her son, in haste address'd:
6150	""
6150	"""Up, Vulcan; up, my son; for we had deem'd"
6150	That eddying Xanthus stood to thee oppos'd:
6150	Haste thee to aid; thy fiery strength display;
6150	While from the sea I call the stormy blast
6150	Of Zephyr and brisk Notus, who shall drive
6150	The raging flames ahead, and burn alike
6150	The Trojans and their arms: do thou the while
6150	Burn down the trees on Xanthus' banks; himself
6150	Assail with fire, nor by his honey'd words
6150	Nor by his menaces be turn'd aside;
6150	Nor, till thou hear my voice, restrain thy pow'r;
6150	"Then stay the raging flames' unwearied course."""
6150	""
6150	Thus Juno spoke; and Vulcan straight prepar'd
6150	The heav'nly fire; and first upon the plain
6150	The flames he kindled, and the dead consum'd,
6150	Who lay, promiscuous, by Achilles slain:
6150	The plain was dried, and stay'd the wat'ry flood.
6150	As when the breath of Boreas quickly dries
6150	In Autumn-time a newly-water'd field,
6150	The tiller's heart rejoicing: so was dried
6150	The spacious plain; then he, the dead consum'd,
6150	Against the river turn'd the fiery glare:
6150	Burnt were the willows, elms, and tamarisk shrubs,
6150	The lotus, and the reeds, and galingal,
6150	Which by the lovely river grew profuse.
6150	The eels and fishes, 'mid the eddying whirl,
6150	'Mid the clear wave were hurrying here and there,
6150	In dire distress from Vulcan's fiery breath:
6150	Scorch'd by the flames, the mighty River spoke:
6150	""
6150	"""Vulcan, no God against thy pow'r can stand,"
6150	Nor with thy fiery flames will I contend;
6150	Restrain thy wrath; though Peleus' godlike son
6150	Should from their city drive the Trojans straight,
6150	"With rival parties what concern have I?"""
6150	""
6150	All scorch'd he spoke; his fair stream bubbling up,
6150	As when a caldron on a blazing fire,
6150	Fill'd with the melting fat of well-fed swine,
6150	Boils up within, and bubbles all around,
6150	With well-dried wood beneath, so bubbling up
6150	The waters of the lovely River boil'd:
6150	Nor onward would he flow, but check'd his course,
6150	By the hot blast o'er-borne, and fiery strength
6150	Of skilful Vulcan; and to Juno thus,
6150	Imploring, he his winged words address'd:
6150	""
6150	"""Juno, what cause impels thy son, my stream,"
6150	O'er all the rest, to visit with his wrath?
6150	E'en less than others who the Trojans aid,
6150	Have I offended; yet at thy command
6150	Will I withdraw; but bid that he too cease;
6150	And this I swear, no Trojan more to save,
6150	Though to devouring flames a prey, all Troy
6150	"Were blazing, kindled by the valiant Greeks."""
6150	""
6150	This when the white-arm'd Goddess Juno heard,
6150	To Vulcan straight she thus address'd her speech:
6150	"""Vulcan, my glorious son, restrain thy hand:"
6150	In mortal men's behalf, it is not meet
6150	"To press thus hardly an Immortal God."""
6150	""
6150	She said, and Vulcan stay'd his fiery strength,
6150	And, back returning, in his wonted bed
6150	Flow'd the fair River. Xanthus thus subdued,
6150	These two their warfare ceas'd, by Juno check'd,
6150	Despite her wrath; but 'mid the other Gods
6150	Arose contention fierce, and discord dire,

6150	With fearful crash they met: the broad Earth groan'd;
6150	Loud rang the Heav'n as with a trumpet's sound:
6150	Jove, on Olympus' height, the tumult heard,
6150	And in his heart he laugh'd a joyous laugh,
6150	To see the Gods in angry battle met.
6150	Not long they stood aloof, led on by Mars

6150	Poising his spear, his bitter speech address'd:
6150	""

6150	The Gods exciting, overbold of mood,

6150	How thou the son of Tydeus, Diomed,
6150	Didst urge against me, and with visible spear
6150	Direct his aim, and aid to wound my flesh?
6150	"For all I suffer'd then, thou now shalt pay."""
6150	""
6150	Thus as he spoke, he struck the tassell'd shield,
6150	Awful to view, which not the lightning bolt
6150	Of Jove himself could pierce: the blood-stain'd Mars

6150	""

6150	"Deserting, aid'st the haughty Trojans' cause."""
6150	""

6150	Him, deeply groaning, scarce to life restor'd,

6150	Beheld, in haste to Pallas thus she cried:
