6150	Undaunted! lo again this saucy jade
6150	Amid the press, the bane of mortals, Mars
6150	"Leads from the field; but haste thee in pursuit."""
6150	""
6150	Thus Juno: Pallas hasten'd in pursuit
6150	Well pleas'd; and Venus with her pow'rful hand
6150	Assailing, struck upon the breast; at once
6150	The Goddess' courage and her limbs gave way.
6150	There on the ground the two together lay,
6150	While Pallas o'er them thus with vaunting speech:
6150	""
6150	"""Would all were such, who aid the Trojan cause,"
6150	Whene'er they meet in fight the warlike Greeks,
6150	As valiant and as stout as Venus proves,
6150	Who brings her aid to Mars, confronting me;
6150	Then had our warlike labours long been o'er,
6150	"And Ilium's strong-built citadel overthrown."""
6150	""
6150	Thus Pallas spoke: the white-arm'd Goddess smil'd,
6150	And to Apollo thus th' Earth-shaker spoke:
6150	""
6150	"""Phoebus, why stand we idly thus aloof?"
6150	The war begun by others, 'tis not meet;
6150	And shame it were, that to Olympus' height
6150	And to the brazen-floor'd abode of Jove
6150	We two without a contest should return.
6150	Thou then begin, as younger: 'twere not well
6150	For me, in age and practice more advanc'd.
6150	Feeble of soul, how senseless is thy heart!
6150	Hast thou forgotten all the cruel wrongs
6150	We two, alone of all th' Immortals, bore,
6150	When here, in Ilium, for a year, we serv'd,
6150	By Jove's command, the proud Laomedon,
6150	For promis'd hire; and he our tasks assign'd?
6150	His fortress, and a wall both broad and fair
6150	I built, the town's impregnable defence;
6150	While thou didst on his plodding herds attend,
6150	In many-crested Ida's woody glens.
6150	But when the joyous seasons, in their course,
6150	Had brought our labour's term, the haughty King
6150	Denied our guerdon, and with threats dismiss'd.
6150	Bound hand and foot, he threaten'd thee to send
6150	And sell to slav'ry in the distant isles,
6150	And with the sword cut off the ears of both.
6150	So in indignant sorrow we return'd,
6150	Robb'd of the hire he promis'd, but denied.
6150	For this thy favour dost thou show to Troy;
6150	And dost not rather join thy force to ours,
6150	That down upon their knees the Trojans all
6150	"Should perish, with their babes and matrons chaste."""
6150	""
6150	Whom answer'd thus the far-destroying King:
6150	"""Earth-shaking God, I should not gain with thee"
6150	The esteem of wise, if I with thee should fight
6150	For mortal men; poor wretches, who like leaves
6150	Flourish awhile, and eat the fruits of earth,
6150	But, sapless, soon decay: from combat then
6150	"Refrain we, and to others leave the strife."""
6150	""
6150	He turn'd, thus saying: for he deem'd it shame
6150	His father's brother to assail in arms;
6150	But him his sister, Goddess of the chase,
6150	Rebuk'd, and thus with scornful speech address'd:
6150	""
6150	"""Fliest thou, Apollo? and to Neptune leav'st"

6150	Ne'er in our father's halls again, as erst
6150	Among th' Immortals, let me hear thee boast
6150	"How thou with Neptune wouldst in arms contend."""
6150	""
6150	Thus she; Apollo answer'd not a word;
6150	But Jove's imperial consort, fill'd with wrath,
6150	Assail'd with bitter words the Archer-Queen.
6150	""
6150	"""How canst thou dare, thou saucy minx, to stand                 [7]"
6150	Oppos'd to me, too great for thine assault,
6150	Despite thy bow? though Jove hath giv'n thee pow'r

6150	Than thy superiors thus to meet in arms,

6150	"And feel how far my might surpasses thine."""
6150	""
6150	She said; and with the left hand both the wrists
6150	Of Dian grasping, with her ample right

6150	And with them, as she turn'd away her head,
6150	With scornful laughter buffeted her ears:
6150	The arrows keen were scatter'd on the ground:
6150	Weeping, the Goddess fled; as flies a dove
6150	The hawk's pursuit, and in a hollow rock
6150	Finds refuge, doom'd not yet to fall a prey;
6150	So, weeping, Dian fled, and left her bow.
6150	""
6150	"Them Hermes to Latona thus: ""With thee"
6150	I strive not; shame it were to meet in fight
6150	A consort of the cloud-compelling Jove.
6150	Freely amid th' Immortals make thy boast,
6150	"That by thy prowess thou hast vanquish'd me."""
6150	""
6150	Thus he: Latona gather'd up the bow,
6150	And fallen arrows, scatter'd here and there
6150	Amid the whirling dust; then, these regain'd,
6150	Following her daughter, from the field withdrew.
6150	Meanwhile to high Olympus fled the Maid,
6150	And to the brazen-floor'd abode of Jove.
6150	There, weeping, on her father's knees she sat,
6150	While quiver'd round her form th' ambrosial robe.
6150	The son of Saturn tow'rds him drew his child,
6150	And thus, with gracious smile, enquiry made:
6150	"""Which of the heav'nly pow'rs hath wrong'd thee thus"
6150	"My child, as guilty of some open shame?"""
6150	""
6150	To whom the bright-crown'd Goddess of the chase:
6150	"""Thy wife, my father, white-arm'd Juno; she"
6150	Hath dealt thus rudely with me; she, from whom
6150	"All jars and strife among the Gods proceed."""
6150	""
6150	Such converse while they held, the gates of Troy
6150	Apollo enter'd, for the well-built wall
6150	Alarm'd, lest e'en against the will of fate
6150	The Greeks that day should raze it to the ground.
6150	The other Gods were to Olympus gone,
6150	Triumphant these, and those in angry mood,
6150	And took their seats before the cloud-girt Sire.
6150	But on the Trojans pressing, Peleus' son
6150	Horses and men alike, promiscuous, slew.
6150	As in a city, which the Gods in wrath
6150	Have fir'd, whose volleying smoke ascends to Heav'n,
6150	On all her people grievous toil is cast,
6150	On many, harm and loss; such toil, such loss
6150	Achilles wrought amid the Trojan host.
6150	""
6150	Upon a lofty tow'r, the work of Gods,
6150	The aged Priam stood, and thence beheld
6150	By fierce Achilles driven in flight confused,
6150	Their courage quite subdued, the Trojan host:
6150	Then, groaning, from the tow'r he hasten'd down,
6150	And to the warders cried along the wall:
6150	""
6150	"""Stand to the gates, and hold them open'd wide,"
6150	That in the crowd of fugitives may pour,
6150	And refuge find; for close upon their flight
6150	Achilles hangs; disaster now is near.
6150	But while our friends, receiv'd within the walls,
6150	Find time to breathe again, replace in haste
6150	The closely-fitting portals; for I fear
6150	"That man of blood may e'en the city storm."""
6150	""
6150	He said; the gates they open'd, and drew back
6150	The solid bars; the portals, op'ning wide,
6150	Let in the light; but in the vacant space
6150	Apollo stood, the Trojan host to save.
6150	The flyers, parch'd with thirst and dust-begrim'd,
6150	Straight for the city and the lofty wall
6150	Made from the plain; Achilles, spear in hand,
6150	Press'd hotly on the rearmost; for his soul
6150	With rage was fill'd, and madd'ning lust of fame.
6150	And now the lofty-gated city of Troy
6150	The sons of Greece had won; but Phoebus rous'd
6150	Agenor's spirit, a valiant youth and strong,
6150	Son of Antenor; he his bosom fill'd
6150	With dauntless courage, and beside him stood
6150	To turn aside the heavy hand of death,
6150	As, veil'd in cloud, against the oak he lean'd.
6150	He, when Achilles' awful form he knew,
6150	Yet firmly stood, though much perplex'd in mind,
6150	As thus he commun'd with his mighty heart:
6150	""
6150	"""Oh woe is me! should I attempt to fly"
6150	Before Achilles' might, where fly the rest
6150	Across the plain, disorder'd, he would soon
6150	O'ertake me, and in flight ignoble slay.
6150	Or should I leave the others to their fate,
6150	Scatter'd by Peleus' son; and from the wall
6150	And o'er the plain of Troy direct my flight,
6150	Far as the foot of Ida's hill, and there
6150	Lie hid in thickest covert; and at eve,
6150	Refresh'd by bathing in the cooling stream,
6150	And purg'd the sweat, retrace my steps to Troy?
6150	Yet why, my soul, admit such thoughts as these?
6150	For should he mark me flying from the town,
6150	And overtake me by his speed of foot,
6150	No hope were left me of escape from death.
6150	So far his strength exceeds the strength of man.
6150	But how if boldly I await him here
6150	Before the wall? his flesh is not to wounds
6150	Impervious: but a single life is his,
6150	Nor is he more, they say, than mortal man,
6150	"Though Jove assists him, and his triumph wills."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and stood collected, to await
6150	Achilles' onset; and his manly heart,
6150	With courage fill'd, was eager for the fray.
6150	As when a panther from the thicket's depth
6150	Comes forth to meet the hunter, undismay'd,
6150	Nor turn'd to flight by baying of the hounds;
6150	Nor, wounded or by jav'lin or by sword,
6150	Or by the spear transfix'd, remits her rage,
6150	But fights, until she reach her foe, or die;
6150	Agenor so, Antenor's godlike son,
6150	Disdain'd to fly, ere prove Achilles' might.
6150	Before his breast his shield's broad orb he bore,
6150	And pois'd his spear, as thus he call'd aloud:
6150	""
6150	"""Thy hope, renown'd Achilles, was this day"
6150	The valiant Trojans' city to destroy;
6150	Unconscious of the toils, the woes, that ye
6150	Around her walls await ye! for within
6150	Are warriors brave and num'rous, who will fight
6150	In her defence, for parents, children, wives.
6150	Thou too, Achilles, here shalt meet thy doom,
6150	"All-pow'rful as thou art, and warrior bold."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and threw with stalwart hand the spear;
6150	Achilles' leg he struck, below the knee,
6150	Nor miss'd his aim; and loudly rang the greaves
6150	Of new-wrought tin; but back the brazen point
6150	Rebounded, nor the heav'nly armour pierc'd.
6150	In turn Achilles on Agenor sprang:
6150	But Phoebus robb'd him of his hop'd-for prize,
6150	Who, veil'd in thickest cloud, convey'd away
6150	Antenor's son, and from the battle bore
6150	To rest in peace; while he by guile withdrew
6150	The son of Peleus from the flying crowd:
6150	For in Agenor's very likeness clad,
6150	Before him stood the far-destroying King:
6150	Then fled, Achilles hast'ning in pursuit.
6150	He o'er the fertile plain with flying foot
6150	Pursu'd; beside Scamander's eddying stream
6150	Apollo turn'd, and still but little space
6150	Before him flying, subtly lur'd him on,
6150	Each moment hoping to attain his prize.
6150	Meantime the gen'ral crowd, in panic flight,
6150	With eager haste the city's refuge sought,
6150	And all the town with fugitives was fill'd.
6150	Nor did they dare without the walls to stand
6150	For mutual aid; nor halt to know what friends
6150	Were safe, who left upon the battle-field;
6150	But through the gates pour'd in the hurrying mass
6150	Who to their active limbs their safety ow'd.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	ARGUMENT.
6150	""
6150	THE DEATH OF HECTOR.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	The Trojans being safe within the walls, Hector only stays to oppose
6150	Achilles. Priam is struck at his approach, and tries to persuade his
6150	son to re-enter the town. Hecuba joins his entreaties, but in vain.
6150	Hector consults within himself what measures to take; but, at the
6150	advance of Achilles, his resolution fails him, and he flies: Achilles
6150	pursues him thrice round the walls of Troy. The gods debate concerning
6150	the fate of Hector; at length Minerva descends to the aid of Achilles.
6150	She deludes Hector in the shape of Deiphobus; he stands the combat, and
6150	is slain. Achilles drags the dead body at his chariot, in the sight of
6150	Priam and Hecuba. Their lamentations, tears, and despair. Their cries
6150	reach the ears of Andromache, who, ignorant of this, was retired into
6150	the inner part of the palace; she mounts up to the walls, and beholds
6150	her dead husband. She swoons at the spectacle. Her excess of grief and
6150	lamentation.
6150	""
6150	The thirtieth day still continues. The scene lies under the walls, and
6150	on the battlements of Troy.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	BOOK XXII.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	Thus they from panic flight, like timorous fawns.
6150	Within the walls escaping, dried their sweat,
6150	And drank, and quench'd their thirst, reclining safe
6150	On the fair battlements; but nearer drew,
6150	With slanted shields, the Greeks; yet Hector still
6150	In front of Ilium and the Scaean gate,
6150	Stay'd by his evil doom, remain'd without;
6150	Then Phoebus thus to Peleus' godlike son:
6150	"""Achilles, why with active feet pursue,"
6150	Thou mortal, me Immortal? know'st thou not
6150	My Godhead, that so hot thy fury burns?
6150	Or heed'st thou not that all the Trojan host
6150	Whom thou hast scar'd, while thou art here withdrawn,
6150	Within the walls a refuge safe have found?
6150	"On me thy sword is vain! I know not death!"""
6150	""
6150	Enrag'd, Achilles, swift of foot, replied:
6150	"""Deep is the injury, far-darting King,"
6150	Most hostile of the Gods, that at thy hand
6150	I bear, who here hast lur'd me from the walls,
6150	Which many a Trojan else had fail'd to reach,
6150	Ere by my hand they bit the bloody dust.
6150	Me of immortal honour thou hast robb'd,
6150	And them, thyself from vengeance safe, hast sav'd.
6150	"Had I the pow'r, that vengeance thou shouldst feel."""
6150	""
6150	Thus saying, and on mightiest deeds intent,
6150	He turn'd him city-ward, with fiery speed;
6150	As when a horse, contending for the prize,
6150	Whirls the swift car, and stretches o'er the plain,
6150	E'en so, with active limbs, Achilles rac'd.
6150	""
6150	Him first the aged Priam's eyes discern'd,
6150	Scouring the plain, in arms all dazzling bright,
6150	Like to th' autumnal star, whose brilliant ray
6150	Shines eminent amid the depth of night,
6150	Whom men the dog-star of Orion call;
6150	The brightest he, but sign to mortal man
6150	Of evil augury, and fiery heat:
6150	So shone the brass upon the warrior's breast.
6150	""
6150	The old man groan'd aloud, and lifting high
6150	His hands, he beat his head, and with loud voice
6150	Call'd on his son, imploring; he, unmov'd,
6150	Held post before the gates, awaiting there
6150	Achilles' fierce encounter; him his sire,
6150	With hands outstretch'd and piteous tone, address'd:
6150	""
6150	"""Hector, my son, await not here alone"
6150	That warrior's charge, lest thou to fate succumb,
6150	Beneath Pelides' arm, thy better far!
6150	Accurs'd be he! would that th' immortal Gods
6150	So favour'd him as I! then should his corpse
6150	Soon to the vultures and the dogs be giv'n!
6150	(So should my heart a load of anguish lose)
6150	By whom I am of many sons bereav'd,
6150	Many and brave, whom he has slain, or sold
6150	To distant isles in slav'ry; and e'en now,
6150	Within the city walls I look in vain
6150	For two, Lycaon brave, and Polydore,
6150	My gallant sons, by fair Laothoe:
6150	If haply yet they live, with brass and gold
6150	Their ransom shall be paid; good store of these
6150	We can command; for with his daughter fair
6150	A wealthy dowry aged Altes gave.
6150	But to the viewless shades should they have gone,
6150	Deep were their mother's sorrow and my own;
6150	But of the gen'ral public, well I know
6150	Far lighter were the grief, than if they heard
6150	That thou hadst fall'n beneath Achilles' hand.
6150	Then enter now, my son, the city gates,
6150	And of the women and the men of Troy,
6150	Be still the guardian; nor to Peleus' son,
6150	With thine own life, immortal glory give.
6150	Look too on me with pity; me, on whom,
6150	E'en on the threshold of mine age, hath Jove
6150	A bitter burthen cast, condemn'd to see
6150	My sons struck down, my daughters dragg'd away
6150	In servile bonds; our chambers' sanctity
6150	Invaded; and our babes by hostile hands
6150	Dash'd to the ground; and by ferocious Greeks
6150	Enslav'd the widows of my slaughter'd sons.
6150	On me at last the rav'ning dogs shall feed,
6150	When by some foeman's hand, by sword or lance,
6150	My soul shall from my body be divorc'd;
6150	Those very dogs which I myself have bred,
6150	Fed at my table, guardians of my gate,
6150	Shall lap my blood, and over-gorg'd shall lie
6150	E'en on my threshold. That a youth should fall
6150	Victim, to Mars, beneath a foeman's spear,
6150	May well beseem his years; and if he fall
6150	With honour, though he die, yet glorious he!
6150	But when the hoary head and hoary beard,
6150	And naked corpse to rav'ning dogs are giv'n,
6150	"No sadder sight can wretched mortals see."""
6150	""
6150	The old man spoke, and from, his head he tore
6150	The hoary hair; yet Hector firm remain'd.
6150	Then to the front his mother rush'd, in tears,
6150	Her bosom bare, with either hand her breast
6150	Sustaining, and with tears address'd him thus:
6150	"""Hector, my child, thy mother's breast revere;"
6150	And on this bosom if thine infant woes
6150	Have e'er been hush'd, bear now in mind, dear child,
6150	The debt thou ow'st; and from within the walls
6150	Ward off this fearful man, nor in the field
6150	Encounter; curs'd be he! should he prevail,
6150	And slay thee, not upon the fun'ral bed,
6150	My child, my own, the offspring of my womb,
6150	Shall I deplore thee, nor thy widow'd wife,
6150	But far away, beside the Grecian ships,
6150	"Thy corpse shall to the rav'ning dogs be giv'n."""
6150	""
6150	Thus they, with tears and earnest pray'rs imploring,
6150	Address'd their son; yet Hector firm remain'd,
6150	Waiting th' approach of Peleus' godlike son.
6150	As when a snake upon the mountain side,
6150	With deadly venom charg'd, beside his hole,
6150	Awaits the traveller, and fill'd with rage,
6150	Coil'd round his hole, his baleful glances darts;
6150	So fill'd with dauntless courage Hector stood,
6150	Scorning retreat, his gleaming buckler propp'd
6150	Against the jutting tow'r; then, deeply mov'd,
6150	Thus with his warlike soul communion held:
6150	""
6150	"""Oh woe is me! if I should enter now"
6150	The city gates, I should the just reproach
6150	Encounter of Polydamas, who first
6150	His counsel gave within the walls to lead
6150	The Trojan forces, on that fatal night
6150	When great Achilles in the field appear'd.
6150	I heeded not his counsel; would I had!
6150	Now, since my folly hath the people slain,
6150	I well might blush to meet the Trojan men,
6150	And long-rob'd dames of Troy, lest some might say,
6150	To me inferior far, 'This woful loss
6150	To Hector's blind self-confidence we owe.'
6150	Thus shall they say; for me, 'twere better far,
6150	Or from Achilles, slain in open fight,
6150	Back to return in triumph, or myself
6150	To perish nobly in my country's cause.
6150	What if my bossy shield I lay aside,
6150	And stubborn helmet, and my pond'rous spear
6150	Propping against the wall, go forth to meet
6150	Th' unmatch'd Achilles? What if I engage
6150	That Helen's self, and with her all the spoil,
6150	And all that Paris in his hollow ships
6150	Brought here to Troy, whence first this war arose,
6150	Should be restor'd; and to the Greeks be paid
6150	An ample tribute from the city's stores,
6150	Her secret treasures; and hereafter bind
6150	The Trojans by their Elders' solemn oaths
6150	Nought to withhold, but fairly to divide
6150	Whate'er of wealth our much-loved city holds?
6150	But wherefore entertain such thoughts, my soul?
6150	Should I so meet him, what if he should show
6150	Nor pity nor remorse, but slay me there,
6150	Defenceless as a woman, and unarm'd?
6150	Not this the time, nor he the man, with whom
6150	By forest oak or rock, like youth and maid,
6150	To hold light talk, as youth and maid might hold.
6150	Better to dare the fight, and know at once
6150	"To whom the vict'ry is decreed by Heav'n."""
6150	""
6150	Thus, as he stood, he mus'd; but near approach'd
6150	Achilles, terrible as plumed Mars;
6150	From his right shoulder brandishing aloft
6150	The ashen spear of Peleus, while around
6150	Flash'd his bright armour, dazzling as the glare
6150	Of burning fire, or of the rising sun.
6150	Hector beheld, and trembled at the sight;
6150	Nor dar'd he there await th' attack, but left
6150	The gates behind, and, terror-stricken, fled.
6150	Forward, with flying foot, Pelides rush'd.
6150	As when a falcon, bird of swiftest flight,
6150	From some high mountain-top, on tim'rous dove
6150	Swoops fiercely down; she, from beneath, in fear,
6150	Evades the stroke; he, dashing through the brake,
6150	Shrill-shrieking, pounces on his destin'd prey;
6150	So, wing'd with desp'rate hate, Achilles flew,
6150	So Hector, flying from his keen pursuit,
6150	Beneath the walls his active sinews plied.
6150	They by the watch-tow'r, and beneath the wall
6150	Where stood the wind-beat fig-tree, rac'd amain
6150	Along the public road, until they reach'd
6150	The fairly-flowing fount whence issu'd forth,
6150	From double source, Scamander's eddying streams.
6150	One with hot current flows, and from beneath,
6150	As from a furnace, clouds of steam arise;
6150	'Mid summer's heat the other rises cold
6150	As hail, or snow, or water crystalliz'd;
6150	Beside the fountains stood the washing-troughs
6150	Of well-wrought stone, where erst the wives of Troy
6150	And daughters fair their choicest garments wash'd,
6150	In peaceful times, ere came the sons of Greece.
6150	There rac'd they, one in flight, and one pursuing;
6150	Good he who fled, but better who pursu'd,
6150	With fiery speed; for on that race was stak'd
6150	No common victim, no ignoble ox:
6150	The prize at stake was mighty Hector's life.
6150	As when the solid-footed horses fly
6150	Around the course, contending for the prize,
6150	Tripod, or woman of her lord bereft;
6150	So rac'd they thrice around the walls of Troy
6150	With active feet; and all the Gods beheld.
6150	Then thus began the Sire of Gods and men:
6150	"""A woful sight mine eyes behold; a man"
6150	I love in flight around the walls! my heart
6150	For Hector grieves, who, now upon the crown
6150	Of deeply-furrow'd Ida, now again
6150	On Ilium's heights, with fat of choicest bulls
6150	Hath pil'd mine altar; whom around the walls,
6150	With flying speed Achilles now pursues.
6150	Give me your counsel, Gods, and say, from death
6150	If we shall rescue him, or must he die,
6150	"Brave as he is, beneath Pelides' hand?"""
6150	""
6150	To whom the blue-ey'd Goddess, Pallas, thus:
6150	"""O Father, lightning-flashing, cloud-girt King,"
6150	What words are these? wouldst thou a mortal man,
6150	Long doom'd by fate, again from death preserve?
6150	"Do as thou wilt, but not with our consent."""
6150	""
6150	To whom the Cloud-compeller thus replied:
6150	"""Be of good cheer, my child! unwillingly"
6150	I speak, yet both thy wishes to oppose:
6150	"Have then thy will, and draw not back thy hand."""
6150	""
6150	His words fresh impulse gave to Pallas' zeal,
6150	And from Olympus' heights in haste she sped.
6150	""
6150	Meanwhile on Hector, with untiring hate.
6150	The swift Achilles press'd: as when a hound,
6150	Through glen and tangled brake, pursues a fawn,
6150	Rous'd from its lair upon the mountain side;
6150	And if awhile it should evade pursuit,
6150	Low crouching in the copse, yet quests he back,
6150	Searching unwearied, till he find the trace;
6150	So Hector sought to baffle, but in vain,
6150	The keen pursuit of Peleus' active son.
6150	Oft as he sought the shelter of the gates
6150	Beneath the well-built tow'rs, if haply thence
6150	His comrades' weapons might some aid afford;
6150	So oft his foeman, with superior speed,
6150	Would cut him off, and turn him to the plain.
6150	He tow'rd the city still essay'd his flight;
6150	And as in dreams, when one pursues in vain,
6150	One seeks in vain to fly, the other seeks
6150	As vainly to pursue; so could not now
6150	Achilles reach, nor Hector quit, his foe.
6150	Yet how should Hector now the doom of death
6150	Have 'scap'd, had not Apollo once again,
6150	And for the last time, to his rescue come,
6150	And giv'n him strength and suppleness of limb?
6150	""
6150	Then to the crowd Achilles with his head
6150	Made sign that none at Hector should presume
6150	To cast a spear, lest one might wound, and so
6150	The greater glory obtain, while he himself
6150	Must be contented with the second place.
6150	But when the fourth time in their rapid course
6150	The founts were reach'd, th' Eternal Father hung
6150	His golden scales aloft, and plac'd in each
6150	The lots of doom, for great Achilles one,
6150	For Hector one, and held them by the midst:
6150	Down sank the scale, weighted with Hector's death,
6150	Down to the shades, and Phoebus left his side.
6150	""
6150	Then to Pelides came the blue-ey'd Maid,
6150	And stood beside him, and bespoke him thus:
6150	"""Achilles, lov'd of Heav'n, I trust that now"
6150	To thee and me great glory shall accrue
6150	In Hector's fall, insatiate of the fight.
6150	Escape he cannot now, though at the feet
6150	Of aegis-bearing Jove, on his behalf,
6150	With earnest pray'r Apollo prostrate fall.
6150	But stay thou here and take thy breath, while I
6150	"Persuade him to return and dare the fight."""
6150	""
6150	So Pallas spoke; and he with joy obeying,
6150	Stood leaning on his brass-barb'd ashen spear.
6150	The Goddess left him there, and went (the form
6150	And voice assuming of Deiphobus)
6150	In search of godlike Hector; him she found,
6150	And standing near, with winged words address'd:
6150	""
6150	"""Sorely, good brother, hast thou been bested"
6150	By fierce Achilles, who around the walls
6150	Hath chas'd thee with swift foot; now stand we both
6150	"For mutual succour, and his onset wait."""
6150	""
6150	To whom great Hector of the glancing helm:
6150	"""Deiphobus, of all my brothers, sons"
6150	Of Hecuba and Priam, thou hast been
6150	Still dearest to my heart; and now the more
6150	I honour thee who dar'st on my behalf,
6150	Seeing my peril, from within the walls
6150	"To sally forth, while others skulk behind."""
6150	""
6150	To whom the blue-ey'd Goddess thus replied:
6150	"""With many pray'rs, good brother, both our sire"
6150	And honour'd mother, and our comrades all
6150	Successively implored me to remain;
6150	Such fear is fall'n on all; but in my soul
6150	On thine account too deep a grief I felt.
6150	Now, forward boldly! spare we not our spears;
6150	Make trial if Achilles to the ships
6150	From both of us our bloody spoils can bear,
6150	"Or by thine arm himself may be subdued."""
6150	""
6150	Thus Pallas lur'd him on with treach'rous wile;
6150	But when the two were met, and close at hand,
6150	First spoke great Hector of the glancing helm:
6150	""
6150	"""No more before thee, Peleus' son, I fly:"
6150	Thrice have I fled around the walls, nor dar'd
6150	Await thine onset; now my spirit is rous'd
6150	To stand before thee, to be slain, or slay.
6150	But let us first th' immortal Gods invoke;
6150	The surest witnesses and guardians they
6150	Of compacts: at my hand no foul disgrace
6150	Shalt thou sustain, if Jove with victory
6150	Shall crown my firm endurance, and thy life
6150	To me be forfeit; of thine armour stripp'd
6150	I promise thee, Achilles, to the Greeks
6150	"Thy body to restore; do thou the like."""
6150	""
6150	With fierce regard Achilles answer'd thus:
6150	"""Hector, thou object of my deadly hate,"
6150	Talk not to me of compacts; as 'tween men
6150	And lions no firm concord can exist,
6150	Nor wolves and lambs in harmony unite,
6150	But ceaseless enmity between them dwells:
6150	So not in friendly terms, nor compact firm,
6150	Can thou and I unite, till one of us
6150	Glut with his blood the mail-clad warrior Mars.
6150	Mind thee of all thy fence; behoves thee now
6150	To prove a spearman skill'd, and warrior brave.
6150	For thee escape is none; now, by my spear,
6150	Hath Pallas doom'd thy death; my comrades' blood,
6150	"Which thou hast shed, shall all be now aveng'd."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and poising, hurl'd his weighty spear;
6150	But Hector saw, and shunn'd the blow; he stoop'd,
6150	And o'er his shoulder flew the brass-tipp'd spear,
6150	And in the ground was fix'd; but Pallas drew
6150	The weapon forth, and to Achilles' hand,
6150	All unobserv'd of Hector, gave it back.
6150	Then Hector thus to Peleus' matchless son:
6150	""
6150	"""Thine aim has fail'd; nor truly has my fate,"
6150	Thou godlike son of Peleus, been to thee
6150	From Heav'n reveal'd; such was indeed thy boast;
6150	But flippant was thy speech, and subtly fram'd
6150	To scare me with big words, and make me prove
6150	False to my wonted prowess and renown.
6150	Not in my back will I receive thy spear,
6150	But through my breast, confronting thee, if Jove
6150	Have to thine arm indeed such triumph giv'n.
6150	Now, if thou canst, my spear in turn elude;
6150	May it be deeply buried in thy flesh!
6150	For lighter were to Troy the load of war,
6150	"If thou, the greatest of her foes, wert slain."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and poising, hurl'd his pond'rous spear;
6150	Nor miss'd his aim; full in the midst he struck
6150	Pelides' shield; but glancing from the shield
6150	The weapon bounded off. Hector was griev'd,
6150	That thus his spear had bootless left his hand.
6150	He stood aghast; no second spear was nigh:
6150	And loudly on Deiphobus he call'd
6150	A spear to bring; but he was far away.
6150	Then Hector knew that he was dup'd, and cried,
6150	"""Oh Heav'n! the Gods above have doom'd my death!"
6150	I deem'd indeed that brave Deiphobus
6150	Was near at hand; but he within the walls
6150	Is safe, and I by Pallas am betray'd.
6150	Now is my death at hand, nor far away:
6150	Escape is none; since so hath Jove decreed,
6150	And Jove's far-darting son, who heretofore
6150	Have been my guards; my fate hath found me now.
6150	Yet not without a struggle let me die,
6150	Nor all inglorious; but let some great act,
6150	"Which future days may hear of, mark my fall."""
6150	""
6150	Thus as he spoke, his sharp-edged sword he drew,
6150	Pond'rous and vast, suspended at his side;
6150	Collected for the spring, and forward dash'd:
6150	As when an eagle, bird of loftiest flight,
6150	Through the dark clouds swoops downward on the plain,
6150	To seize some tender lamb, or cow'ring hare;
6150	So Hector rush'd, and wav'd his sharp-edg'd sword.
6150	Achilles' wrath was rous'd: with fury wild
6150	His soul was fill'd: before his breast he bore
6150	His well-wrought shield; and fiercely on his brow

6150	By Vulcan's hand was thickly interlac'd;

6150	Hesper, the brightest star that shines in Heav'n,

6150	Where from attack his body least was fenc'd.
6150	All else the glitt'ring armour guarded well,
6150	Which Hector from Patroclus' corpse had stripp'd;
6150	One chink appear'd, just where the collar-bone
6150	The neck and shoulder parts, beside the throat,
6150	Where lies expos'd the swiftest road of death.
6150	There levell'd he, as Hector onward rush'd;
6150	Right through the yielding neck the lance was driv'n,
6150	But sever'd not the windpipe, nor destroy'd
6150	His pow'r of speech; prone in the dust he fell;
6150	And o'er him, vaunting, thus Achilles spoke:
6150	""
6150	"""Hector, Patroclus stripping of his arms,"
6150	Thy hope was that thyself wast safe; and I,
6150	Not present, brought no terror to thy soul:
6150	Fool! in the hollow ships I yet remain'd,
6150	I, his avenger, mightier far than he;
6150	I, who am now thy conqu'ror. By the dogs
6150	And vultures shall thy corpse be foully torn,
6150	"While him the Greeks with fun'ral rites shall grace."""
6150	""
6150	Whom answer'd Hector of the glancing helm,
6150	"Prostrate and helpless: ""By thy soul, thy knees,"
6150	Thy parents' heads, Achilles, I beseech,
6150	Let not my corpse by Grecian dogs be torn.
6150	Accept the ample stores of brass and gold,
6150	Which as my ransom by my honour'd sire
6150	And mother shall be paid thee; but my corpse
6150	Restore, that so the men and wives of Troy
6150	"May deck with honours due my fun'ral pyre."""
6150	""
6150	To whom, with fierce aspect, Achilles thus:
6150	"""Knee me no knees, vile hound! nor prate to me"
6150	Of parents! such my hatred, that almost
6150	I could persuade myself to tear and eat
6150	Thy mangled flesh; such wrongs I have to avenge,
6150	He lives not, who can save thee from the dogs;
6150	Not though with ransom ten and twenty fold
6150	He here should stand, and yet should promise more;
6150	No, not though Priam's royal self should sue
6150	To be allow'd for gold to ransom thee;
6150	No, not e'en so, thy mother shall obtain
6150	To lay thee out upon the couch, and mourn
6150	O'er thee, her offspring; but on all thy limbs
6150	"Shall dogs and carrion vultures make their feast."""
6150	""
6150	To whom thus Hector of the glancing helm,
6150	"Dying: ""I know thee well; nor did I hope"
6150	To change thy purpose; iron is thy soul.
6150	But see that on thy head I bring not down
6150	The wrath of Heav'n, when by the Scaean gate
6150	The hand of Paris, with Apollo's aid,
6150	"Brave warrior as thou art, shall strike thee down."""
6150	""
6150	E'en as he spoke, his eyes were clos'd in death;
6150	And to the viewless shades his spirit fled,
6150	Mourning his fate, his youth and vigour lost.
6150	""
6150	To him, though dead, Achilles thus replied:
6150	"""Die thou! my fate I then shall meet, whene'er"
6150	"Jove and th' immortal Gods shall so decree."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and from the corpse his spear withdrew,
6150	And laid aside; then stripp'd the armour off,
6150	With, blood besmear'd; the Greeks around him throng'd,
6150	Gazing on Hector's noble form and face,
6150	And none approach'd that did not add a wound:
6150	"And one to other look'd, and said, ""Good faith,"
6150	Hector is easier far to handle now,
6150	"Then when erewhile he wrapp'd our ships in fire."""
6150	Thus would they say, then stab the dead anew.
6150	""
6150	But when the son of Peleus, swift of foot,
6150	Had stripp'd the armour from the corpse, he rose,
6150	And, standing, thus th' assembled Greeks address'd:
6150	"""O friends, the chiefs and councillors of Greece,"
6150	Since Heav'n hath granted us this man to slay,
6150	Whose single arm hath wrought us more of ill
6150	Than all the rest combin'd, advance we now
6150	Before the city in arms, and trial make
6150	What is the mind of Troy; if, Hector slain,
6150	They from the citadel intend retreat,
6150	Or still, despite their loss, their ground maintain.
6150	But wherefore entertain such thoughts, my soul?
6150	Beside the ships, unwept, unburied, lies
6150	Patroclus: whom I never can forget,
6150	While number'd with the living, and my limbs
6150	Have pow'r to move; in Hades though the dead
6150	May be forgotten, yet e'en there will I
6150	The mem'ry of my lov'd companion keep.
6150	Now to the ships return we, sons of Greece,
6150	Glad paeans singing! with us he shall go;
6150	Great glory is ours, the godlike Hector slain,
6150	"The pride of Troy, and as a God rever'd."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and foully Hector's corpse misus'd;
6150	Of either foot he pierc'd the tendon through,
6150	That from the ancle passes to the heel,
6150	And to his chariot bound with leathern thongs,
6150	Leaving the head to trail along the ground;
6150	Then mounted, with the captur'd arms, his car,
6150	And urg'd his horses; nothing loth, they flew.
6150	A cloud of dust the trailing body rais'd:
6150	Loose hung his glossy hair; and in the dust
6150	Was laid that noble head, so graceful once;
6150	Now to foul insult doom'd by Jove's decree,
6150	In his own country, by a foeman's hand.
6150	So lay the head of Hector; at the sight
6150	His aged mother tore her hair, and far
6150	From off her head the glitt'ring veil she threw,
6150	And with loud cries her slaughter'd son bewail'd.
6150	Piteous, his father groan'd; and all around
6150	Was heard the voice of wailing and of woe.
6150	Such was the cry, as if the beetling height
6150	Of Ilium all were smould'ring in the fire.
6150	Scarce in his anguish could the crowd restrain
6150	The old man from issuing through the Dardan gates;
6150	Low in the dust he roll'd, imploring all,
6150	Entreating by his name each sev'ral man:
6150	"""Forbear, my friends; though sorrowing, stay me not;"
6150	Leave me to reach alone the Grecian ships,
6150	And there implore this man of violence,
6150	This haughty chief, if haply he my years
6150	May rev'rence, and have pity on my age.
6150	For he too has a father, like to me;
6150	Peleus, by whom he was begot, and bred,
6150	The bane of Troy; and, most of all, to me
6150	The cause of endless grief, who by his hand
6150	Have been of many stalwart sons bereft.
6150	Yet all, though griev'd for all, I less lament,
6150	Than one, whose loss will sink me to the grave,
6150	Hector! oh would to Heav'n that in mine arms
6150	He could have died; with mourning then and tears
6150	We might have satisfied our grief, both she
6150	"Who bore him, hapless mother, and myself."""
6150	""
6150	Weeping, he spoke; and with him wept the crowd:
6150	Then, 'mid the women, Hecuba pour'd forth
6150	"Her vehement grief: ""My child, oh whither now,"
6150	Heart-stricken, shall I go, of thee bereft,
6150	Of thee, who wast to me by night and day
6150	A glory and a boast; the strength of all
6150	The men of Troy, and women? as a God
6150	They worshipp'd thee: for in thy life thou wast
6150	"The glory of all; but fate hath found thee now."""
6150	""
6150	Weeping, she spoke; but nought as yet was known
6150	To Hector's wife; to her no messenger
6150	Had brought the tidings, that without the walls
6150	Remained her husband; in her house withdrawn
6150	A web she wove, all purple, double woof,
6150	With varied flow'rs in rich embroidery,
6150	And to her neat-hair'd maidens gave command
6150	To place the largest caldrons on the fire,
6150	That with warm baths, returning from the fight,
6150	Hector might be refresh'd; unconscious she,
6150	That by Achilles' hand, with Pallas' aid,
6150	Far from the bath, was godlike Hector slain.
6150	The sounds of wailing reach'd her from the tow'r;
6150	Totter'd her limbs, the distaff left her hand,
6150	And to her neat-hair'd maidens thus she spoke:
6150	"""Haste, follow me, some two, that I may know"
6150	What mean these sounds; my honour'd mother's voice
6150	I hear; and in my breast my beating heart
6150	Leaps to my mouth; my limbs refuse to move;
6150	Some evil, sure, on Priam's house impends.
6150	Be unfulfill'd my words! yet much I fear
6150	Lest my brave Hector be cut off alone,
6150	By great Achilles, from the walls of Troy,
6150	Chas'd to the plain, the desp'rate courage quench'd,
6150	Which ever led him from the gen'ral ranks
6150	"Far in advance, and bade him yield to none."""
6150	""
6150	Then from the house she rush'd, like one distract,
6150	With beating heart; and with her went her maids.
6150	But when she reach'd the tow'r, where stood the crowd,
6150	And mounted on the wall, she look'd around,
6150	And saw the body which with insult foul
6150	The flying steeds were dragging towards the ships;
6150	Then sudden darkness overspread her eyes;
6150	Backward she fell, and gasp'd her spirit away.
6150	Far off were flung th' adornments of her head,
6150	The net, the fillet, and the woven bands;
6150	The nuptial veil by golden Venus giv'n,
6150	That day when Hector of the glancing helm
6150	Led from Eetion's house his wealthy bride.
6150	The sisters of her husband round her press'd,
6150	And held, as in the deadly swoon she lay.
6150	But when her breath and spirit return'd again,
6150	With sudden burst of anguish thus she cried:
6150	"""Hector, oh woe is me! to misery"
6150	We both were born alike; thou here in Troy
6150	In Priam's royal palace; I in Thebes,
6150	By wooded Placos, in Eetion's house,
6150	Who nurs'd my infancy; unhappy he,
6150	Unhappier I! would I had ne'er been born!
6150	Now thou beneath the depths of earth art gone,
6150	Gone to the viewless shades; and me hast left
6150	A widow in thy house, in deepest woe;
6150	Our child, an infant still, thy child and mine,
6150	Ill-fated parents both! nor thou to him,
6150	Hector, shalt be a guard, nor he to thee:
6150	For though he 'scape this tearful war with Greece,
6150	Yet nought for him remains but ceaseless woe,
6150	And strangers on his heritage shall seize.
6150	No young companions own the orphan boy:
6150	With downcast eyes, and cheeks bedew'd with tears,
6150	His father's friends approaching, pinch'd with want,
6150	He hangs upon the skirt of one, of one
6150	He plucks the cloak; perchance in pity some
6150	May at their tables let him sip the cup,
6150	Moisten his lips, but scarce his palate touch;
6150	While youths, with both surviving parents bless'd,
6150	May drive him from their feast with blows and taunts,
6150	'Begone! thy father sits not at our board:'
6150	Then weeping, to his widow'd mother's arms
6150	He flies, that orphan boy, Astyanax,
6150	Who on his father's knees erewhile was fed
6150	On choicest marrow, and the fat of lambs;
6150	And, when in sleep his childish play was hush'd,
6150	Was lull'd to slumber in his nurse's arms
6150	On softest couch, by all delights surrounded.
6150	But grief, his father lost, awaits him now,
6150	Astyanax, of Trojans so surnam'd,
6150	Since thou alone wast Troy's defence and guard.
6150	But now on thee, beside the beaked ships,
6150	Far from thy parents, when the rav'ning dogs
6150	Have had their fill, the wriggling worms shall feed;
6150	On thee, all naked; while within thy house
6150	Lies store of raiment, rich and rare, the work
6150	Of women's hands; these will I burn with fire;
6150	Not for thy need--thou ne'er shalt wear them more,--
6150	"But for thine honour in the sight of Troy."""
6150	""
6150	Weeping she spoke; the women join'd her wail.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	ARGUMENT.
6150	""
6150	FUNERAL GAMES IN HONOUR OF PATROCLUS.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	Achilles and the Myrmidons do honour to the body of Patroclus. After
6150	the funeral feast he retires to the sea-shore, where, falling asleep,
6150	the ghost of his friend appears to him, and demands the rites of
6150	burial: the next morning the soldiers are sent with mules and waggons
6150	to fetch wood for the pyre. The funeral procession, and the offering
6150	their hair to the dead. Achilles sacrifices several animals, and
6150	lastly, twelve Trojan captives, at the pile; then sets fire to it. He
6150	pays libations to the winds, which (at the instance of Iris) rise, and
6150	raise the flame. When the pile has burned all night, they gather the
6150	bones, place them in an urn of gold, and raise the tomb. Achilles
6150	institutes the funeral games: the chariot-race, the fight of the
6150	caestus, the wrestling, the footrace, the single combat, the discus,
6150	the shooting with arrows, the darting the javelin: the various
6150	descriptions of which, and the various success of the several
6150	antagonists, make the greatest part of the book.
6150	""
6150	In this book ends the thirtieth day: the night following, the ghost of
6150	Patroclus appears to Achilles: the one-and-thirtieth day is employed in
6150	felling the timber for the pile; the two-and-thirtieth in burning it;
6150	and the three-and-thirtieth in the games. The scene is generally on the
6150	sea-shore.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	BOOK XXIII.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	Thus they throughout the city made their moan;
6150	But when the Greeks had come where lay their ships
6150	By the broad Hellespont, their sev'ral ways
6150	They each pursu'd, dispersing; yet not so
6150	Achilles let his Myrmidons disperse,
6150	But thus his warlike comrades he address'd:
6150	""
6150	"""My faithful comrades, valiant Myrmidons,"
6150	Loose we not yet our horses from the cars;
6150	But for Patroclus mourn, approaching near,
6150	With horse and car; such tribute claim the dead;
6150	Then, free indulgence to our sorrows giv'n,
6150	"Loose we the steeds, and share the ev'ning meal."""
6150	""
6150	He said; and they with mingled voices rais'd
6150	The solemn dirge; Achilles led the strain;
6150	Thrice round the dead they drove their sleek-skinn'd steeds,
6150	Mourning, with hearts by Thetis grief-inspir'd;
6150	With tears the sands, with tears the warriors' arms,
6150	Were wet; so mighty was the chief they mourn'd.
6150	Then on his comrade's breast Achilles laid
6150	His blood-stain'd hands, and thus began the wail:
6150	""
6150	"""All hail, Patroclus, though in Pluto's realm;"
6150	All that I promis'd, lo! I now perform;
6150	That on the corpse of Hector, hither dragg'd,
6150	Our dogs should feed; and that twelve noble youths,
6150	The sons of Troy, before thy fun'ral pyre,
6150	"My hand, in vengeance for thy death, should slay."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and foully Hector's corpse misus'd,
6150	Flung prostrate in the dust, beside the couch
6150	Where lay Menoetius' son. His comrades then
6150	Their glitt'ring armour doff'd, of polish'd brass,
6150	And loos'd their neighing steeds; then round the ship
6150	Of Peleus' son in countless numbers sat,
6150	While he th' abundant fun'ral feast dispens'd.
6150	There many a steer lay stretch'd beneath the knife,
6150	And many a sheep, and many a bleating goat,
6150	And many a white-tusk'd porker, rich in fat,
6150	There lay extended, singeing o'er the fire;
6150	And blood, in torrents, flow'd around the corpse.
6150	To Agamemnon then the Kings of Greece
6150	The royal son of Peleus, swift of foot,
6150	Conducted; yet with him they scarce prevail'd;
6150	So fierce his anger for his comrade's death.
6150	But when to Agamemnon's tent they came,
6150	He to the clear-voic'd heralds gave command
6150	An ample tripod on the fire to place;
6150	If haply Peleus' son he might persuade
6150	To wash away the bloody stains of war:
6150	But sternly he, and with an oath refus'd.
6150	""
6150	"""No, by great Jove I swear, of all the Gods"
6150	Highest and mightiest, water shall not touch
6150	This head of mine, till on the fun'ral pyre
6150	I see the body of Patroclus laid,
6150	And build his tomb, and cut my votive hair;
6150	For while I live and move 'mid mortal men,
6150	No second grief like this can pierce my soul.
6150	Observe we now the mournful fun'ral feast;
6150	But thou, great Agamemnon, King of men,
6150	Send forth at early dawn, and to the camp
6150	Bring store of fuel, and all else prepare,
6150	That with provision meet the dead may pass
6150	Down to the realms of night; so shall the fire
6150	From out our sight consume our mighty dead,
6150	"And to their wonted tasks the troops return."""
6150	""
6150	He said; they listen'd, and his words obey'd;
6150	Then busily the ev'ning meal prepar'd,
6150	And shar'd the social feast; nor lack'd there aught.
6150	The rage of thirst and hunger satisfied,
6150	Each to their sev'ral tents the rest repair'd;
6150	But on the many-dashing ocean's shore
6150	Pelides lay, amid his Myrmidons,
6150	With bitter groans; in a clear space he lay,
6150	Where broke the waves, continuous, on the beach.
6150	There, circumfus'd around him, gentle sleep,
6150	Lulling the sorrows of his heart to rest,
6150	O'ercame his senses; for the hot pursuit
6150	Of Hector round the breezy heights of Troy
6150	His active limbs had wearied: as he slept,
6150	Sudden appear'd Patroclus' mournful shade,
6150	His very self; his height, and beauteous eyes,
6150	And voice; the very garb he wont to wear:
6150	Above his head it stood, and thus it spoke:
6150	""
6150	"""Sleep'st thou, Achilles, mindless of thy friend,"
6150	Neglecting, not the living, but the dead?
6150	Hasten, my fun'ral rites, that I may pass
6150	Through Hades' gloomy gates; ere those be done,
6150	The spirits and spectres of departed men
6150	Drive me far from them, nor allow to cross
6150	Th' abhorred river; but forlorn and sad
6150	I wander through the wide-spread realms of night.
6150	And give me now thy hand, whereon to weep;
6150	For never more, when laid upon the pyre,
6150	Shall I return from Hades; never more,
6150	Apart from all our comrades, shall we two,
6150	As friends, sweet counsel take; for me, stern Death,
6150	The common lot of man, has op'd his mouth;
6150	Thou too, Achilles, rival of the Gods,
6150	Art destin'd here beneath the walls of Troy
6150	To meet thy doom; yet one thing must I add,
6150	And make, if thou wilt grant it, one request.
6150	Let not my bones be laid apart from thine,
6150	Achilles, but together, as our youth
6150	Was spent together in thy father's house,
6150	Since first my sire Menoetius me a boy
6150	From Opus brought, a luckless homicide,
6150	Who of Amphidamas, by evil chance,
6150	Had slain the son, disputing o'er the dice:
6150	Me noble Peleus in his house receiv'd,
6150	And kindly nurs'd, and thine attendant nam'd;
6150	So in one urn be now our bones enclos'd,
6150	"The golden vase, thy Goddess-mother's gift."""
6150	""
6150	Whom answer'd thus Achilles, swift of foot:
6150	"""Why art thou here, lov'd being? why on me"
6150	These sev'ral charges lay? whate'er thou bidd'st
6150	Will I perform, and all thy mind fulfil;
6150	But draw thou near; and in one short embrace,
6150	"Let us, while yet we may, our grief indulge."""
6150	""
6150	Thus as he spoke, he spread his longing arms,
6150	But nought he clasp'd; and with a wailing cry,
6150	Vanish'd, like smoke, the spirit beneath the earth.
6150	Up sprang Achilles, all amaz'd, and smote
6150	His hands together, and lamenting cried:
6150	""
6150	"""O Heav'n, there are then, in the realms below,"
6150	Spirits and spectres, unsubstantial all;
6150	For through the night Patroclus' shade hath stood,
6150	Weeping and wailing, at my side, and told
6150	"His bidding; th' image of himself it seem'd."""
6150	""
6150	He said; his words the gen'ral grief arous'd:
6150	To them, as round the piteous dead they mourn'd,
6150	Appear'd the rosy-finger'd morn; and straight,
6150	From all the camp, by Agamemnon sent,
6150	Went forth, in search of fuel, men and mules,
6150	Led by a valiant chief, Meriones,
6150	The follower of renown'd Idomeneus.
6150	Their felling axes in their hands they bore,
6150	And twisted ropes; their mules before them driv'n;
6150	Now up, now down, now sideways, now aslope,
6150	They journey'd on; but when they reach'd the foot
6150	Of spring-abounding Ida, they began
6150	With axes keen to hew the lofty oaks;
6150	They, loudly crashing, fell: the wood they clove,
6150	And bound it to the mules; these took their way
6150	Through the thick brushwood, hurrying to the plain.
6150	The axe-men too, so bade Meriones,
6150	The follower of renown'd Idomeneus,
6150	Were laden all with logs, which on the beach
6150	They laid in order, where a lofty mound,
6150	In mem'ry of Patroclus and himself,
6150	Achilles had design'd. When all the store
6150	Of wood was duly laid, the rest remain'd
6150	In masses seated; but Achilles bade
6150	The warlike Myrmidons their armour don,
6150	And harness each his horses to his car;
6150	They rose and donn'd their arms, and on the cars
6150	Warriors and charioteers their places took.
6150	""
6150	First came the horse, and then a cloud of foot,
6150	Unnumber'd; in the midst Patroclus came,
6150	Borne by his comrades; all the corpse with hair
6150	They cover'd o'er, which from their heads they shore.
6150	Behind, Achilles held his head, and mourn'd
6150	The noble friend whom to the tomb he bore.
6150	Then on the spot by Peleus' son assign'd,
6150	They laid him down, and pil'd the wood on high.
6150	Then a fresh thought Achilles' mind conceiv'd:
6150	Standing apart, the yellow locks he shore,
6150	Which as an off'ring to Sperchius' stream,
6150	He nurs'd in rich profusion; sorrowing then
6150	Look'd o'er the dark-blue sea, as thus lie spoke:
6150	""
6150	"""Sperchius, all in vain to thee his pray'r"
6150	My father Peleus made, and vow'd that I,
6150	Return'd in safety to my native land,
6150	To thee should dedicate my hair, and pay
6150	A solemn hecatomb, with sacrifice
6150	Of fifty rams, unblemish'd, to the springs
6150	Where on thy consecrated soil is plac'd
6150	Thine incense-honour'd altar; so he vow'd;
6150	But thou the boon withhold'st; since I no more
6150	My native land may see, the hair he vow'd,
6150	"To brave Patroclus thus I dedicate."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and on his comrade's hand he laid
6150	The locks; his act the gen'ral grief arous'd;
6150	And now the setting sun had found them still
6150	Indulging o'er the dead; but Peleus' son
6150	Approaching, thus to Agamemnon spoke:
6150	""
6150	"""Atrides, for to thee the people pay"
6150	Readiest obedience, mourning too prolong'd
6150	May weary; thou then from the pyre the rest
6150	Disperse, and bid prepare the morning meal;
6150	Ours be the farther charge, to whom the dead
6150	"Was chiefly dear; yet let the chiefs remain."""
6150	""
6150	The monarch Agamemnon heard, and straight
6150	Dispers'd the crowd amid their sev'ral ships.
6150	Th' appointed band remain'd, and pil'd the wood.
6150	A hundred feet each way they built the pyre,
6150	And on the summit, sorrowing, laid the dead.
6150	Then many a sheep and many a slow-paced ox
6150	They flay'd and dress'd around the fun'ral pyre;
6150	Of all the beasts Achilles took the fat,
6150	And cover'd o'er the corpse from head to foot,
6150	And heap'd the slaughter'd carcases around;
6150	Then jars of honey plac'd, and fragrant oils,
6150	Resting upon the couch; next, groaning loud,
6150	Four pow'rful horses on the pyre he threw;
6150	Then, of nine dogs that at their master's board
6150	Had fed, he slaughter'd two upon his pyre;
6150	Last, with the sword, by evil counsel sway'd,
6150	Twelve noble youths he slew, the sons of Troy.
6150	The fire's devouring might he then applied,
6150	And, groaning, on his lov'd companion call'd:
6150	""
6150	"""All hail, Patroclus, though in Pluto's realm!"
6150	All that I promis'd, lo! I now perform:
6150	On twelve brave sons of Trojan sires, with thee,
6150	The flames shall feed; but Hector, Priam's son,
6150	"Not to the fire, but to the dogs I give."""
6150	""
6150	Such was Achilles' threat, but him the dogs
6150	Molested not; for Venus, night and day
6150	Daughter of Jove, the rav'ning dogs restrain'd;
6150	And all the corpse o'erlaid with roseate oil,
6150	Ambrosial, that though dragg'd along the earth,
6150	The noble dead might not receive a wound.
6150	Apollo too a cloudy veil from Heav'n
6150	Spread o'er the plain, and cover'd all the space
6150	Where lay the dead, nor let the blazing sun
6150	The flesh upon his limbs and muscles parch.
6150	""
6150	Yet burnt not up Patroclus' fun'ral pyre;
6150	Then a fresh thought Achilles' mind conceiv'd:
6150	"Standing apart, on both the ""Winds he call'd,"
6150	Boreas and Zephyrus, and added vows
6150	Of costly sacrifice; and pouring forth
6150	Libations from a golden goblet, pray'd
6150	Their presence, that the wood might haste to burn,
6150	And with the fire consume the dead; his pray'r
6150	Swift Iris heard, and bore it to the Winds.
6150	They in the hall of gusty Zephyrus
6150	Were gather'd round the feast; in haste appearing,
6150	Swift Iris on the stony threshold stood.
6150	They saw, and rising all, besought her each
6150	To sit beside him; she with their requests
6150	Refus'd compliance, and address'd them thus:
6150	""
6150	"""No seat for me; for I o'er th' ocean stream"
6150	From hence am bound to AEthiopia's shore,
6150	To share the sacred feast, and hecatombs,
6150	Which there they offer to th' immortal Gods;
6150	But, Boreas, thee, and loud-voic'd Zephyrus,
6150	With vows of sacrifice, Achilles calls
6150	To fan the fun'ral pyre, whereon is laid
6150	"Patroclus, mourn'd by all the host of Greece."""
6150	""
6150	She said, and vanish'd; they, with rushing sound,
6150	Rose, and before them drove the hurrying clouds:
6150	Soon o'er the sea they swept; the stirring breeze
6150	Ruffled the waves; the fertile shores of Troy
6150	They reach'd, and falling on the fun'ral pyre,
6150	Loud roar'd the crackling flames; they all night long
6150	With current brisk together fann'd the fire.
6150	All night Achilles from a golden bowl
6150	Drew forth, and, in his hand a double cup,
6150	The wine outpouring, moisten'd all the earth,
6150	Still calling on his lost Patroclus' shade.
6150	As mourns a father o'er a youthful son,
6150	Whose early death hath wrung his parents' hearts;
6150	So mourn'd Achilles o'er his friend's remains,
6150	Prostrate beside the pyre, and groan'd aloud.
6150	But when the star of Lucifer appear'd,
6150	The harbinger of light, whom following close
6150	Spreads o'er the sea the saffron-robed morn,
6150	Then pal'd the smould'ring fire, and sank the flame;
6150	And o'er the Thracian sea, that groan'd and heav'd
6150	Beneath their passage, home the Winds return'd;
6150	And weary, from the pyre a space withdrawn,
6150	Achilles lay, o'ercome by gentle sleep.
6150	""
6150	Anon, awaken'd by the tramp and din
6150	Of crowds that follow'd Atreus' royal son,
6150	He sat upright, and thus address'd his speech:
6150	""
6150	"""Thou son of Atreus, and ye chiefs of Greece,"
6150	Far as the flames extended, quench we first
6150	With ruddy wine the embers of the pyre;
6150	And of Menoetius' son, Patroclus, next
6150	With care distinguishing, collect the bones;
6150	Nor are they hard to know; for in the midst
6150	He lay, while round the edges of the pyre,
6150	Horses and men commix'd, the rest were burnt.
6150	Let these, between a double layer of fat
6150	Enclos'd, and in a golden urn remain,
6150	Till I myself shall in the tomb be laid;
6150	And o'er them build a mound, not over-large,
6150	But of proportions meet; in days to come,
6150	Ye Greeks, who after me shall here remain,
6150	"Complete the work, and build it broad and high."""
6150	""
6150	Thus spoke Achilles; they his words obey'd:
6150	Far as the flames had reach'd, and thickly strown
6150	The embers lay, they quench'd with ruddy wine;
6150	Then tearfully their gentle comrade's bones
6150	Collected, and with double layers of fat
6150	Enclos'd, and in a golden urn encas'd;
6150	Then in the tent they laid them, overspread
6150	With veil of linen fair; then meting out
6150	Th' allotted space, the deep foundations laid
6150	Around the pyre, and o'er them heap'd the earth.
6150	Their task accomplished, all had now withdrawn;
6150	But Peleus' son the vast assembly stay'd,
6150	And bade them sit; then, prizes of the games,
6150	Tripods and caldrons from the tents he brought,
6150	And noble steeds, and mules, and sturdy steers,
6150	And women fair of form, and iron hoar.
6150	""
6150	First, for the contest of the flying cars
6150	The prizes he display'd: a woman fair,
6150	Well skill'd in household cares; a tripod vast,
6150	Two-handled, two and twenty measures round;
6150	These both were for the victor: for the next,
6150	A mare, unbroken, six years old, in foal
6150	Of a mule colt; the third, a caldron bright,
6150	Capacious of four measures, white and pure,
6150	By fire as yet untarnish'd; for the fourth,
6150	Of gold two talents; for the fifth, a vase
6150	With double cup, untouch'd by fire, he gave.
6150	Then, standing up, he thus address'd the Greeks:
6150	""
6150	"""Thou son of Atreus, and ye well-greav'd Greeks,"
6150	Before ye are the prizes, which await
6150	The contest of the cars; but if, ye Greeks,
6150	For any other cause these games were held,
6150	I to my tent should bear the foremost prize;
6150	For well ye know how far my steeds excel,
6150	Steeds of immortal race, which Neptune gave
6150	To Peleus, he to me, his son, transferr'd.
6150	But from the present strife we stand aloof,
6150	My horses and myself; they now have lost
6150	The daring courage and the gentle hand
6150	Of him who drove them, and with water pure
6150	Wash'd oft their manes, and bath'd with fragrant oil.
6150	For him they stand and mourn, with drooping heads
6150	Down to the ground, their hearts with sorrow fill'd;
6150	But ye in order range yourselves, who boast
6150	"Your well-built chariots and your horses' speed."""
6150	""
6150	He said: up sprang the eager charioteers;
6150	The first of all, Eumelus, King of men,
6150	Son of Admetus, matchless charioteer;
6150	Next, Tydeus' son, the valiant Diomed,
6150	With Trojan horses, from AEneas won,
6150	When by Apollo's aid himself escap'd;
6150	Then Heav'n-born Menelaus, Atreus' son,
6150	Two flying coursers harness'd to his car;
6150	His own, Podargus, had for yokefellow
6150	AEthe, a mare by Agamemnon lent:
6150	Her, Echepolus to Atrides gave,
6150	Anchises' son, that to the wars of Troy
6150	He might not be compell'd, but safe at home
6150	Enjoy his ease; for Jove had bless'd his store
6150	With ample wealth, in Sicyon's wide domain.
6150	Her now he yok'd, impatient for the course.
6150	The fourth, Antilochus, the gallant son
6150	Of Nestor, son of Neleus, mighty chief,
6150	Harness'd his sleek-skinn'd steeds; of Pylian race
6150	Were they who bore his car; to him, his sire
6150	Sage counsel pour'd in understanding ears:
6150	""
6150	"""Antilochus, though young in years thou art,"
6150	Yet Jove and Neptune love thee, and have well
6150	Instructed thee in horsemanship; of me
6150	Thou need'st no counsel; skill'd around the goal
6150	To whirl the chariot; but thou hast, of all,
6150	The slowest horses: whence I augur ill.
6150	But though their horses have the speed of thine,
6150	In skill not one of them surpasses thee.
6150	Then thou, dear boy, exert thine ev'ry art,
6150	That so thou mayst not fail to gain a prize.
6150	By skill, far more than strength, the woodman fells
6150	The sturdy oak; by skill the steersman guides
6150	His flying ship across the dark-blue sea,
6150	Though shatter'd by the blast; 'twixt charioteer
6150	And charioteer 'tis skill that draws the line.
6150	One, vainly trusting to his coursers' speed,
6150	Drives reckless here and there; o'er all the course,
6150	His horses, unrestrain'd, at random run.
6150	Another, with inferior horses far,
6150	But better skill'd, still fixing on the goal
6150	His eye, turns closely round, nor overlooks
6150	The moment when to draw the rein; but holds
6150	His steady course, and on the leader waits.
6150	A mark I give thee now, thou canst not miss:
6150	There stands a wither'd trunk, some six feet high,
6150	Of oak, or pine, unrotted by the rain;
6150	On either side have two white stones been plac'd,
6150	Where meet two roads; and all around there lies
6150	A smooth and level course; here stood perchance
6150	The tomb of one who died long years ago;
6150	Or former generations here have plac'd,
6150	As now Achilles hath decreed, a goal.
6150	There drive, as only not to graze the post;
6150	And leaning o'er the wicker body, leave
6150	Close on the left the stones; thine offside horse
6150	Then urge with voice and whip, and slack his rein,
6150	And let the nearside horse so closely graze,
6150	As that thy nave may seem to touch, the goal:
6150	But yet beware, lest, striking on the stone,
6150	Thy steeds thou injure, and thy chariot break,
6150	A source of triumph to thy rivals all,
6150	Of shame to thee; but thou sage caution use;
6150	For, following, if thou make the turn the first,
6150	Not one of all shall pass thee, or o'ertake;
6150	Not though Arion's self were in the car,
6150	Adrastus' flying steed, of heav'nly race,
6150	"Nor those which here Laomedon possess'd."""
6150	""
6150	This said, and to his son his counsels giv'n,
6150	The aged Nestor to his seat withdrew.
6150	Fifth in the lists Meriones appear'd.
6150	They mounted on their cars, and cast their lots:
6150	Achilles shook the helmet; first leaped forth
6150	The lot of Nestor's son, Antilochus;
6150	Next came the King Eumelus; after whom
6150	The valiant Menelaus, Atreus' son;
6150	The fourth, Meriones; and last of all,
6150	But ablest far, Tydides drew his place.
6150	They stood in line; Achilles pointed out,
6150	Ear on the level plain, the distant goal;
6150	And there in charge the godlike Phoenix plac'd,
6150	His father's ancient follower, to observe
6150	The course assign'd, and true report to make.
6150	Then all at once their whips they rais'd, and urg'd
6150	By rein, and hand, and voice, their eager steeds.
6150	They from the ships pursued their rapid course
6150	Athwart the distant plain; beneath their chests
6150	Rose like a cloud, or hurricane, the dust;
6150	Loose floated on the breeze their ample manes;
6150	The cars now skimm'd along the fertile ground,
6150	Now bounded high in air; the charioteers
6150	Stood up aloft, and ev'ry bosom beat
6150	With hope of vict'ry; each with eager shout
6150	Cheering his steeds, that scour'd the dusty plain.
6150	But when, the farthest limits of the course
6150	Attain'd, they turn'd beside the hoary sea,
6150	Strain'd to their utmost speed, were plainly seen
6150	The qualities of each; then in the front
6150	Appear'd Eumelus' flying mares, and next
6150	The Trojan horses of Tydides came:
6150	Nor these were far behind, but following close
6150	They seem'd in act to leap upon the car.
6150	Eumelus, on his neck and shoulders broad,
6150	Felt their warm breath; for o'er him, as they flew,
6150	Their heads were downward bent; and now, perchance,
6150	Had he or pass'd, or made an even race,
6150	But that, incens'd with valiant Diomed,
6150	Apollo wrested from his hands the whip.
6150	Then tears of anger from his eyelids fell,
6150	As gaining more and more the mares he saw,
6150	While, urg'd no more, his horses slack'd their speed.
6150	But Pallas mark'd Apollo's treach'rous wile;
6150	And hasting to the chief, restor'd his whip,
6150	And to his horses strength and courage gave.
6150	The Goddess then Admetus' son pursued,
6150	And snapp'd his chariot yoke; the mares, releas'd,
6150	Swerv'd from the track; the pole upon the ground
6150	Lay loosen'd from the car; and he himself
6150	Beside the wheel was from the chariot hurl'd.
6150	From elbows, mouth, and nose, the skin was torn;
6150	His forehead crush'd and batter'd in; his eyes
6150	Were fill'd with tears, and mute his cheerful voice.
6150	Tydides turn'd aside, and far ahead
6150	Of all the rest, pass'd on; for Pallas gave
6150	His horses courage, and his triumph will'd.
6150	Next him, the fair-hair'd Menelaus came,
6150	The son of Atreus; but Antilochus
6150	Thus to his father's horses call'd aloud:
6150	""
6150	"""Forward, and stretch ye to your utmost speed;"
6150	I ask you not with those of Diomed
6150	In vain to strive, whom Pallas hath endued
6150	With added swiftness, and his triumph will'd;
6150	But haste ye, and o'ertake Atrides' car,
6150	Nor be by AEthe, by a mare, disgrac'd.
6150	Why, my brave horses, why be left behind?
6150	This too I warn ye, and will make it good:
6150	No more at Nestor's hand shall ye receive
6150	Your provender, but with the sword be slain,
6150	If by your faults a lower prize be ours;
6150	Then rouse ye now, and put forth all your speed,
6150	And I will so contrive, as not to fail
6150	"Of slipping past them in the narrow way."""
6150	""
6150	He said; the horses, of his voice in awe,
6150	Put forth their pow'rs awhile; before them soon
6150	Antilochus the narrow pass espied.
6150	It was a gully, where the winter's rain
6150	Had lain collected, and had broken through
6150	A length of road, and hollow'd out the ground:
6150	There Menelaus held his cautious course.
6150	Fearing collision; but Antilochus,
6150	Drawing his steeds a little from the track,
6150	Bore down upon him sideways: then in fear,
6150	The son of Atreus to Antilochus
6150	"Shouted aloud, ""Antilochus, thou driv'st"
6150	Like one insane; hold in awhile thy steeds;
6150	Here is no space; where wider grows the road,
6150	There thou mayst pass; but here, thou wilt but cause
6150	"Our cars to clash, and bring us both to harm."""
6150	""
6150	He said; but madlier drove Antilochus,
6150	Plying the goad, as though he heard him not.
6150	""
6150	Far as a discus' flight, by some stout youth,
6150	That tests his vigour, from the shoulder hurl'd,
6150	So far they ran together, side by side:
6150	Then dropp'd Atrides' horses to the rear,
6150	For he himself forbore to urge their speed,
6150	Lest, meeting in the narrow pass, the cars
6150	Should be o'erthrown, and they themselves, in haste
6150	To gain the vict'ry, in the dust be roll'd.
6150	Then thus, reproachful, to Antilochus:
6150	""
6150	"""Antilochus, thou most perverse of men!"
6150	Beshrew thy heart! we Greeks are much deceiv'd
6150	Who give thee fame for wisdom! yet e'en now
6150	"Thou shalt not gain, but on thine oath, the prize."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and to his horses call'd aloud:
6150	"""Slack not your speed, nor, as defeated, mourn;"
6150	Their legs and feet will sooner tire than yours,
6150	"For both are past the vigour of their youth."""
6150	Thus he; the horses, of his voice in awe,
6150	Put forth their pow'rs, and soon the leaders near'd.
6150	""
6150	Meanwhile the chieftains, seated in the ring,
6150	Look'd for the cars, that scour'd the dusty plain.
6150	The first to see them was Idomeneus,
6150	The Cretan King; for he, without the ring,
6150	Was posted high aloft; and from afar
6150	He heard and knew the foremost horseman's voice;
6150	Well too he knew the gallant horse that led,
6150	All bay the rest, but on his front alone
6150	A star of white, full-orbed as the moon:
6150	Then up he rose, and thus the Greeks address'd:
6150	""
6150	"""O friends, the chiefs and councillors of Greece,"
6150	Can ye too see, or I alone, the cars?
6150	A diff'rent chariot seems to me in front,
6150	A diff'rent charioteer; and they who first
6150	Were leading, must have met with some mischance.
6150	I saw them late, ere round the goal they turn'd,
6150	But see them now no more; though all around
6150	My eyes explore the wide-spread plain of Troy.
6150	Perchance the charioteer has dropp'd the reins,
6150	Or round the goal he could not hold the mares;
6150	Perchance has miss'd the turn, and on the plain
6150	Is lying now beside his broken car,
6150	While from the course his mettled steeds have flown.
6150	Stand up, and look yourselves; I cannot well
6150	Distinguish; but to me it seems a chief,
6150	Who reigns o'er Greeks, though of AEtolian race,
6150	"The son of Tydeus, valiant Diomed."""
6150	""
6150	Sharply Oileus' active son replied:
6150	"""Idomeneus, why thus, before the time,"
6150	So rashly speak? while the high-stepping steeds
6150	Are speeding yet across the distant plain.
6150	Thine eyes are not the youngest in the camp,
6150	Nor look they out the sharpest from thy head;
6150	But thou art ever hasty in thy speech,
6150	And ill becomes thee this precipitance.
6150	Since others are there here, thy betters far.
6150	The same are leading now, that led at first,
6150	"Eumelus' mares; 'tis he that holds the reins."""
6150	""
6150	To whom in anger thus the Cretan chief:
6150	"""Ajax, at wrangling good, in judgment naught,"
6150	And for aught else, among the chiefs of Greece
6150	Of small account--so stubborn is thy soul;
6150	Wilt thou a tripod or a caldron stake,
6150	And Agamemnon, Atreus' son, appoint
6150	The umpire to decide whose steeds are first?
6150	"So shalt thou gain thy knowledge at thy cost."""
6150	""
6150	He said; up sprang Oileus' active son,
6150	In anger to reply; and farther yet
6150	Had gone the quarrel, but Achilles' self
6150	Stood up, and thus the rival chiefs address'd:
6150	""
6150	"""Forbear, both Ajax and Idomeneus,"
6150	This bitter interchange of wordy war;
6150	It is not seemly; and yourselves, I know,
6150	Another would condemn, who so should speak.
6150	But stay ye here, and seated in the ring,
6150	Their coming wait; they, hurrying to the goal,
6150	Will soon be here; and then shall each man know
6150	"Whose horses are the second, whose the first."""
6150	""
6150	Thus he; but Tydeus' son drew near, his lash
6150	Still laid upon his horses' shoulder-points;
6150	As lightly they, high-stepping, scour'd the plain.
6150	Still on the charioteer the dust was flung;
6150	As close upon the flying-footed steeds
6150	Follow'd the car with gold and tin inlaid;
6150	And lightly, as they flew along, were left
6150	Impress'd the wheel-tracks on the sandy plain.
6150	There in the midst he stood, the sweat profuse
6150	Down-pouring from his horses' heads and chests;
6150	Down from the glitt'ring car he leap'd to earth,
6150	And lean'd his whip against the chariot yoke;
6150	Nor long delay'd the valiant Sthenelus,
6150	But eagerly sprang forth to claim the prize;
6150	Then to his brave companions gave in charge
6150	To lead away the woman, and to bear
6150	The tripod, while himself unyok'd the steeds.
6150	""
6150	Nest came the horses of Antilochus,
6150	Who had by stratagem, and not by speed,
6150	O'er Menelaus triumph'd; yet e'en so
6150	Atrides' flying coursers press'd him hard;
6150	For but so far as from the chariot-wheel
6150	A horse, when harness'd to a royal car;
6150	Whose tail, back-streaming, with the utmost hairs
6150	Brushes the felloes; close before the wheel,
6150	Small space between, he scours the wide-spread plain:
6150	So far was Menelaus in the rear
6150	Of Nestor's son; at first, a discus' cast
6150	Between them lay; but rapidly his ground
6150	He gain'd--so well the speed and courage serv'd
6150	Of AEthe, Agamemnon's beauteous mare;
6150	And, but a little farther were the course,
6150	Had pass'd him by, nor left the race in doubt.
6150	Behind the noble son of Atreus came,
6150	A jav'lin's flight apart, Meriones,
6150	The faithful follower of Idomeneus:
6150	His were the slowest horses, and himself
6150	The least experienc'd in the rapid race.
6150	Dragging his broken car, came last of all,
6150	His horses driv'n in front, Admetus' son;
6150	Achilles swift of foot with pity saw,
6150	And to the Greeks his winged words address'd:
6150	""
6150	"""See where the best of all the last appears;"
6150	But let him take, as meet, the second prize;
6150	"The first belongs of right to Tydeus' son."""
6150	""
6150	Thus he; they all assented to his words;
6150	And, by the gen'ral voice of Greece, the mare
6150	Had now been his; but noble Nestor's son,
6150	Antilochus, stood up, his right to claim,
6150	And to Achilles, Peleus' son, replied:
6150	"""Achilles, thou wilt do me grievous wrong,"
6150	If thou thy words accomplish; for my prize
6150	Thou tak'st away, because mishap befell
6150	His car and horses, by no fault of his;
6150	Yet had he to th' Immortals made his pray'r,
6150	He surely had not thus been last of all.
6150	But, pitying him, if so thy mind incline,
6150	Thy tents contain good store of gold, and brass,
6150	And sheep, and female slaves, and noble steeds;
6150	For him, of these, hereafter mayst thou take
6150	A prize of higher value; or e'en now,
6150	And with th' applause of all; but for the mare,
6150	I will not give her up; and let who will
6150	"Stand forth, my own right hand shall guard my prize."""
6150	""
6150	He said; and smil'd Achilles swift of foot,
6150	Delighted; for he lov'd the noble youth,
6150	To whom his winged words he thus address'd:
6150	""
6150	"""Antilochus, if such be thy request,"
6150	That for Eumelus I should add a prize,
6150	This too I grant thee; and to him I give
6150	My breastplate, from Asteropaeus won,
6150	Of brass, around whose edge is roll'd a stream

6150	""

6150	He went, and brought it; in Eumelus' hand

6150	Burning with wrath against Antilochus;
6150	And while the herald in the monarch's hand
6150	His royal sceptre plac'd, and bade the Greeks
6150	Keep silence, thus the godlike hero spoke:
6150	""
6150	"""Antilochus, till now reputed wise,"
6150	What hast thou done? thou hast impugn'd my skill,
6150	And sham'd my horses, who hast brought thine own,
6150	Inferior far, before them to the goal.
6150	But come, ye chiefs and councillors of Greece,
6150	Judge ye between us, fav'ring neither side:
6150	That none of all the brass-clad Greeks may say
6150	That Menelaus hath by false reports
6150	O'erborne Antilochus, and holds his prize:
6150	His horses fairly worsted, and himself
6150	Triumphant only by superior pow'r.
6150	Or come now, I myself will judgment give;
6150	Nor deem I any Greek will find to blame
6150	In my decision, for 'tis fair and just.
6150	Antilochus, come forward, noble chief;
6150	And standing, as 'tis meet, before the car
6150	And horses, in thy hand the slender whip
6150	Wherewith thou drov'st, upon the horses lay
6150	Thy hand, and by Earth-shaking Neptune swear

6150	""
6150	To whom Antilochus with prudent speech:
6150	"""Have patience with me yet; for I, O King,"
6150	O Menelaus, am thy junior far;
6150	My elder and superior thee I own.
6150	Thou know'st th' o'er-eager vehemence of youth,

6150	""

6150	And plac'd in Menelaus' hands the mare:
6150	The monarch's soul was melted, like the dew
6150	Which glitters on the ears of growing corn,

6150	""
6150	"""Antilochus, at once I lay aside"

6150	""
6150	He said, and to Noemon gave in charge,

6150	"For age with heavy hand hath bow'd thee down."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and plac'd it in his hand; th' old man
6150	Beceiv'd with joy the gift, and thus replied:
6150	""
6150	"""All thou hast said, my son, is simple truth:"
6150	No firmness now my limbs and feet retain,
6150	Nor can my arms with freedom, as of old,
6150	Straight from the shoulder, right and left, strike out.
6150	Oh that such youth and vigour yet were mine,
6150	As when th' Epeians in Buprasium held
6150	The royal Amarynceus' fun'ral games,
6150	And when the monarch's sons his prizes gave!
6150	Then could not one of all th' Epeian race,
6150	Or Pylians, or AEtolians, vie with me.
6150	In boxing, Clytomedes, OEnops' son,
6150	I vanquished; then Anchaeus, who stood up
6150	To wrestle with me, I with ease o'erthrew;
6150	Iphiclus I outran, though fleet of foot;
6150	In hurling with the spear, with Phyleus strove,
6150	And Polydorus, and surpass'd them both.
6150	The sons of Actor in the chariot-race
6150	Alone o'ercame me; as in number more,                           [8]
6150	And grudging more my triumph, since remain'd,
6150	This contest to reward, the richest prize.
6150	They were twin brothers; one who held the reins,
6150	Still drove, and drove; the other plied the whip.
6150	Such was I once; but now must younger men
6150	Engage in deeds like these; and I, the chief
6150	Of heroes once, must bow to weary age.
6150	But honour thou with fitting fun'ral games
6150	Thy comrade: I accept, well-pleas'd, thy gift,

6150	The place of honour, which among the Greeks
