6150	"Reward thee with a worthy recompense!"""
6150	""
6150	He said; Achilles listen'd to the praise
6150	Of Neleus' son; then join'd the gen'ral throng.
6150	Next, he set forth the prizes, to reward
6150	The labours of the sturdy pugilists;
6150	A hardy mule he tether'd in the ring,
6150	Unbroken, six years old, most hard to tame;
6150	And for the vanquished man, a double cup;
6150	Then rose, and to the Greeks proclaim'd aloud:
6150	""
6150	"""Thou son of Atreus, and ye well-greav'd Greeks,"
6150	For these we bid two champions brave stand forth.
6150	And in the boxer's manly toil contend;
6150	And he, whose stern endurance Phoebus crowns
6150	With vict'ry, recogniz'd by all the Greeks,
6150	He to his tent shall lead the hardy mule;
6150	"The loser shall the double cup receive."""
6150	""
6150	He said; up sprang Epeius, tall and stout,
6150	A boxer skill'd, the son of Panopeus,
6150	Who laid his hand upon the mule, and said:
6150	""
6150	"""Stand forth, if any care the cup to win;"
6150	The mule, methinks, no Greek can bear away
6150	From me, who glory in the champion's name.
6150	Is't not enough, that in the battle-field
6150	I claim no special praise? 'tis not for man
6150	In all things to excel; but this I say,
6150	And will make good my words, who meets me here,
6150	I mean to pound his flesh, and smash his bones.
6150	See that his seconds be at hand, and prompt
6150	"To bear him from the ring, by me subdued."""
6150	""
6150	He said; they all in silence heard his speech:
6150	Only Euryalus, a godlike chief,
6150	Son of Mecistheus, Talaion's son,
6150	Stood forth opposing; he had once in Thebes
6150	Join'd in the fun'ral games of OEdipus,
6150	And there had vanquish'd all of Cadmian race.
6150	On him attended valiant Diomed,
6150	With cheering words, and wishes of success.
6150	Around his waist he fasten'd first the belt,
6150	Then gave the well-cut gauntlets for his hands.
6150	Of wild bull's hide. When both were thus equipp'd,
6150	Into the centre of the ring they stepp'd:
6150	There, face to face, with sinewy arms uprais'd,
6150	They stood awhile, then clos'd; strong hand with hand
6150	Mingling, in rapid interchange of blows.
6150	Dire was the clatter of their jaws; the sweat
6150	Pour'd forth, profuse, from ev'ry limb; then rush'd
6150	Epeius on, and full upon the cheek,
6150	Half turn'd aside, let fall a stagg'ring blow;
6150	Nor stood Euryalus; but, legs and feet
6150	Knock'd from beneath him, prone to earth he fell;
6150	And as a fish, that flounders on the sand,
6150	Thrown by rude Boreas on the weedy beach,
6150	Till cover'd o'er by the returning wave;
6150	So flounder'd he beneath that stunning blow.
6150	But brave Epeius took him by the hand,
6150	And rais'd him up; his comrades crowded round
6150	And bore him from the field, with dragging steps,
6150	Spitting forth clotted gore, his heavy head
6150	Rolling from side to side; within his tent
6150	They laid him down, unconscious; to the ring
6150	Then back returning, bore away the cup.
6150	""
6150	Achilles next before the Greeks display'd
6150	The prizes of the hardy wrestlers' skill:
6150	The victor's prize, a tripod vast, fire-proof,
6150	And at twelve oxen by the Greeks apprais'd;
6150	And for the vanquish'd man, a female slave
6150	Pric'd at four oxen, skill'd in household work.
6150	Then rose, and loudly to the Greeks proclaim'd,
6150	"""Stand forth, whoe'er this contest will essay."""
6150	""
6150	He said; and straight uprose the giant form
6150	Of Ajax Telamon; with him uprose
6150	Ulysses, skill'd in ev'ry crafty wile.
6150	Girt with the belt, within the ring they stood,
6150	And each, with stalwart grasp, laid hold on each;
6150	As stand two rafters of a lofty house,
6150	Each propping each, by skilful architect
6150	Design'd the tempest's fury to withstand.
6150	Creak'd their backbones beneath the tug and strain
6150	Of those strong arms; their sweat pour'd down like rain;
6150	And bloody weals of livid purple hue
6150	Their sides and shoulders streak'd, as sternly they
6150	For vict'ry and the well-wrought tripod strove.
6150	Nor could Ulysses Ajax overthrow,
6150	Nor Ajax bring Ulysses to the ground,
6150	So stubbornly he stood; but when the Greeks
6150	Were weary of the long-protracted strife,
6150	Thus to Ulysses mighty Ajax spoke:
6150	"""Ulysses sage, Laertes' godlike son,"
6150	Or lift thou me, or I will thee uplift:
6150	"The issue of our struggle rests with Jove."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and rais'd Ulysses from the ground;
6150	Nor he his ancient craft remember'd not,
6150	But lock'd his leg around, and striking sharp
6150	Upon the hollow of the knee, the joint
6150	Gave way; the giant Ajax backwards fell,
6150	Ulysses on his breast; the people saw,
6150	And marvell'd. Then in turn Ulysses strove
6150	Ajax to lift; a little way he mov'd,
6150	But fail'd to lift him fairly from, the ground;
6150	Yet crook'd his knee, that both together fell,
6150	And side by side, defil'd with dust, they lay.
6150	""
6150	And now a third encounter had they tried
6150	But rose Achilles, and the combat stay'd:
6150	""
6150	"""Forbear, nor waste your strength, in farther strife;"
6150	Ye both are victors; both then bear away
6150	An equal meed of honour; and withdraw,
6150	"That other Greeks may other contests wage."""
6150	Thus spoke Achilles: they his words obey'd,
6150	And brushing off the dust, their garments donn'd.
6150	""
6150	The prizes of the runners, swift of foot,
6150	Achilles next set forth; a silver bowl,
6150	Six measures its content, for workmanship
6150	Unmatch'd on earth, of Sidon's costliest art
6150	The product rare; thence o'er the misty sea
6150	Brought by Phoenicians, who, in port arriv'd,
6150	Gave it to Thoas; by Euneus last,
6150	The son of Jason, to Patroclus paid,
6150	In ransom of Lycaon, Priam's son;
6150	Which now Achilles, on his friend's behalf,
6150	Assign'd as his reward, whoe'er should prove
6150	The lightest foot, and speediest in the race.
6150	A steer, well fatten'd, was the second prize,
6150	And half a talent, for the third, of gold.
6150	He rose, and to the Greeks proclaim'd aloud,
6150	"""Stand forth, whoe'er this contest will essay."""
6150	He said: uprose Oileus' active son;
6150	Uprose Ulysses, skill'd in ev'ry wile,
6150	And noble Nestor's son, Antilochus,
6150	Who all the youth in speed of foot surpass'd.
6150	They stood in line: Achilles pointed out
6150	The limits of the course; as from the goal
6150	They stretch'd them to the race, Oileus' son
6150	First shot ahead; Ulysses following close;
6150	Nor farther than the shuttle from the breast
6150	Of some fair woman, when her outstretch'd arm
6150	Has thrown the woof athwart the warp, and back
6150	Withdraws it tow'rd her breast; so close behind
6150	Ulysses press'd on Ajax, and his feet
6150	Trod in his steps, ere settled yet the dust.
6150	His breath was on his shoulders, as the plain
6150	He lightly skimm'd; the Greeks with eager shouts
6150	Still cheering, as he strain'd to win the prize.
6150	But as they near'd the goal, Ulysses thus
6150	To blue-ey'd Pallas made his mental pray'r:
6150	"""Now hear me, Goddess, and my feet befriend."""
6150	Thus as he pray'd, his pray'r the Goddess heard,
6150	And all his limbs with active vigour fill'd;
6150	And, as they stretch'd their hands to seize the prize,
6150	Tripp'd up by Pallas, Ajax slipp'd and fell,
6150	Amid the offal of the lowing kine
6150	Which o'er Patroclus Peleus' son had slain.
6150	His mouth and nostrils were with offal fill'd.
6150	First in the race, Ulysses bore away
6150	The silver bowl; the steer to Ajax fell;
6150	And as upon the horn he laid his hand,
6150	Sputt'ring the offal out, he call'd aloud:
6150	"""Lo, how the Goddess has my steps bewray'd,"
6150	"Who guards Ulysses with a mother's care."""
6150	Thus as he spoke, loud laugh'd the merry Greeks.
6150	Antilochus the sole remaining prize
6150	Receiv'd, and, laughing, thus the Greeks address'd:
6150	""
6150	"""I tell you, friends, but what yourselves do know,"
6150	How of the elder men th' immortal Gods
6150	Take special care; for Ajax' years not much
6150	Exceed mine own; but here we see a man,
6150	One of a former age, and race of men;
6150	A hale old man we call him; but for speed
6150	"Not one can match him, save Achilles' self."""
6150	""
6150	Thus he, with praise implied of Peleus' son;
6150	To whom in answer thus Achilles spoke:
6150	""
6150	"""Antilochus, not unobserv'd of me"
6150	Nor unrewarded shall thy praise remain:
6150	"To thy half talent add this second half."""
6150	""
6150	Thus saying, in his hand he plac'd the gold;
6150	Antilochus with joy the gift receiv'd.
6150	""
6150	Next, in the ring the son of Peleus laid
6150	A pond'rous spear, a helmet, and a shield,
6150	The spoil Patroclus from Sarpedon won;
6150	Then rose, and loudly to the Greeks proclaim'd:
6150	""
6150	"""For these we call upon two champions brave"
6150	To don their arms, their sharp-edg'd weapons grasp,
6150	And public trial of their prowess make;
6150	And he who first his rival's flesh shall reach,
6150	And, through his armour piercing, first draw blood,
6150	He shall this silver-studded sword receive,
6150	My trophy from Asteropaeus won,
6150	Well-wrought, of Thracian metal; but the arms
6150	In common property they both shall hold,
6150	"And in my tent a noble banquet share."""
6150	""
6150	He said; uprose great Ajax Telamon,
6150	And Tydeus' son, the valiant Diomed.
6150	First, from the crowd apart, they donn'd their arms;
6150	Then, eager for the fight, with haughty stare
6150	Stood in the midst; the Greeks admiring gaz'd.
6150	When, each approaching other, near they came,
6150	Thrice rush'd they on, and thrice in combat clos'd.
6150	Then through the buckler round of Diomed
6150	Great Ajax drove his spear; nor reach'd the point
6150	Tydides' body, by the breastplate stay'd:
6150	While, aim'd above the mighty shield's defence,
6150	His glitt'ring weapon flash'd at Ajax' throat.
6150	For Ajax fearing, shouted then the Greeks
6150	To cease the fight, and share alike the prize;
6150	But from Achilles' hand the mighty sword,
6150	With belt and scabbard, Diomed receiv'd.
6150	""
6150	Next in the ring the son of Peleus plac'd
6150	A pond'rous mass of iron, as a quoit
6150	Once wielded by Eetion's giant strength,
6150	But to the ships with other trophies borne,
6150	When by Achilles' hand Eetion fell.
6150	Then rose, and loudly to the Greeks proclaim'd:
6150	"""Stand forth, whoe'er this contest will essay."
6150	This prize who wins, though widely may extend
6150	His fertile fields, for five revolving years
6150	It will his wants supply; nor to the town
6150	For lack of iron, with this mass in store,
6150	"Need he his shepherd or his ploughman send."""
6150	""
6150	He said; and valiant Polypoetes rose,
6150	Epeius, and Leonteus' godlike strength,
6150	And mighty Ajax, son of Telamon.
6150	In turns they took their stand; Epeius first
6150	Uprais'd the pond'rous mass, and through the air
6150	Hurl'd it, amid the laughter of the Greeks.
6150	Next came Leonteus, scion true of Mars;
6150	The third was Ajax; from whose stalwart hand
6150	Beyond the farthest mark the missile flew.
6150	But when the valiant Polypoetes took
6150	The quoit in hand, far as a herdsman throws
6150	His staff, that, whirling, flies among the herd;
6150	So far beyond the ring's extremest bound
6150	He threw the pond'rous mass; loud were the shouts;
6150	And noble Polypoetes' comrades rose,
6150	And to the ships the monarch's gift convey'd.
6150	""
6150	The archers' prizes next, of iron hoar,
6150	Ten sturdy axes, double-edg'd, he plac'd,
6150	And single hatchets ten; then far away
6150	Rear'd on the sand a dark-prow'd vessel's mast,
6150	On which, with slender string, a tim'rous dove
6150	Was fasten'd by the foot, the archers' mark;
6150	That who should strike the dove should to his tent
6150	The axes bear away; but who the string
6150	Should sever, but should fail to strike the bird,
6150	As less in skill, the hatchets should receive.
6150	Thus spoke Achilles; straight uprose the might
6150	Of royal Teucer, and Meriones,
6150	The faithful follower of Idomeneus.
6150	They in a brass-bound helmet shook the lots.
6150	The first was Teucer's; with impetuous force
6150	He shot; but vow'd not to the Archer-King
6150	Of firstling lambs a solemn hecatomb.
6150	The dove he struck not, for the Archer-God
6150	Withheld his aid; but close beside her foot
6150	The arrow sever'd the retaining string.
6150	The bird releas'd, soar'd heav'nward; while the string
6150	Dropp'd, from the mast suspended, tow'rds the earth,
6150	And loudly shouted their applause the Greeks.
6150	Then snatch'd Meriones in haste the bow
6150	From Teucer's hand; his own already held
6150	His arrow, pointed straight; he drew the string,
6150	And to the far-destroying King he vow'd
6150	Of firstling lambs a solemn hecatomb.
6150	Aloft amid the clouds he mark'd the dove,
6150	And struck her, as she soar'd, beneath the wing;
6150	Right through the arrow pass'd; and to the earth
6150	Returning, fell beside Meriones.
6150	The bird upon the dark-prow'd vessel's mast
6150	Lighted awhile; anon, with drooping head,
6150	And pinions flutt'ring vain, afar she fell,
6150	Lifeless; th' admiring crowd with wonder gaz'd.
6150	Meriones the axes bore away,
6150	While Teucer to the ships the hatchets bore.
6150	""
6150	Last, in the ring the son of Peleus laid
6150	A pond'rous spear, and caldron, burnish'd bright,
6150	Pric'd at an ox's worth, untouch'd by fire,
6150	For those who with the jav'lin would contend.
6150	Uprose then Agamemnon, King of men,
6150	The son of Atreus, and Meriones,
6150	The faithful follower of Idomeneus:
6150	But Peleus' godlike son address'd them thus:
6150	""
6150	"""How far, Atrides, thou excell'st us all,"
6150	And with the jav'lin what thy pow'r and skill
6150	Pre-eminent, we know; take thou this prize,
6150	And bear it to thy ships; and let us give
6150	To brave Meriones the brazen spear;
6150	"If so it please thee, such were my advice."""
6150	""
6150	He said; and Agamemnon, King of men,
6150	Assenting, gave to brave Meriones
6150	The brazen spear; while in Talthybius' care,
6150	His herald, plac'd the King his noble prize.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	ARGUMENT.
6150	""
6150	THE REDEMPTION OF THE BODY OF HECTOR.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	The gods deliberate about the redemption of Hector's body. Jupiter
6150	sends Thetis to Achilles to dispose him for the restoring it, and Iris
6150	to Priam, to encourage him to go in person, and treat for it. The old
6150	king, notwithstanding the remonstrances of his queen, makes ready for
6150	the journey, to which he is encouraged by an omen from Jupiter. He sets
6150	forth in his chariot, with a waggon loaded with presents, under the
6150	charge of Idaeus the herald. Mercury descends in the shape of a young
6150	man, and conducts him to the pavilion of Achilles. Their conversation
6150	on the way* Priam finds Achilles at his table, casts himself at his
6150	feet, and begs for the body of his son; Achilles, moved with
6150	compassion, grants his request, detains him one night in his tent, and
6150	the next morning sends him home with the body; the Trojans run out to
6150	meet him. The lamentation of Andromache, Hecuba, and Helen, with the
6150	solemnities of the funeral.
6150	""
6150	The time of twelve days is employed in this book, while the body of
6150	Hector lies in the tent of Achilles. And as many more are spent in the
6150	truce allowed for his interment. The scene is partly in Achilles' camp,
6150	and partly in Troy.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	""
6150	BOOK XXIV.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	The games were ended, and the multitude
6150	Amid the ships their sev'ral ways dispers'd:
6150	Some to their supper, some to gentle sleep
6150	Yielding, delighted; but Achilles still
6150	Mourn'd o'er his lov'd companion; not on him
6150	Lighted all-conqu'ring sleep, but to and fro
6150	Restless he toss'd, and on Patroclus thought,
6150	His vigour and his courage; all the deeds
6150	They two together had achiev'd; the toils,
6150	The perils they had undergone, amid
6150	The strife of warriors, and the angry waves.
6150	Stirr'd by such mem'ries, bitter tears he shed;
6150	Now turning on his side, and now again
6150	Upon his back; then prone upon his face;
6150	Then starting to his feet, along the shore
6150	All objectless, despairing, would he roam;
6150	Nor did the morn, above the sea appearing,
6150	Unmark'd of him arise; his flying steeds
6150	He then would harness, and, behind the car
6150	The corpse of Hector trailing in the dust,
6150	Thrice make the circuit of Patroclus' tomb;
6150	Then would he turn within his tent to rest,
6150	Leaving the prostrate corpse with dust defil'd;
6150	But from unseemly marks the valiant dead
6150	Apollo guarded, who with pity view'd
6150	The hero, though in death; and round him threw
6150	His golden aegis; nor, though dragg'd along,
6150	Allow'd his body to receive a wound.
6150	""
6150	Thus foully did Achilles in his rage
6150	Misuse the mighty dead; the blessed Gods
6150	With pitying grief beheld the sight, and urg'd
6150	That Hermes should by stealth the corpse remove.
6150	The counsel pleas'd the rest; but Juno still,
6150	And Neptune, and the blue-ey'd Maid, retain'd
6150	The hatred, unappeas'd, with which of old
6150	Troy and her King and people they pursued;
6150	Since Paris to the rival Goddesses,
6150	Who to his sheepfold came, gave deep offence,
6150	Preferring her who brought him in return
6150	The fatal boon of too successful love.
6150	But when the twelfth revolving day was come,
6150	Apollo thus th' assembled Gods address'd:
6150	"""Shame on ye, Gods, ungrateful! have ye not,"
6150	At Hector's hand, of bulls and choicest goats
6150	Receiv'd your off'rings meet? and fear ye now
6150	E'en his dead corpse to save, and grant his wife,
6150	His mother, and his child, his aged sire
6150	And people, to behold him, and to raise
6150	His fun'ral pile, and with due rites entomb?
6150	But fell Achilles all your aid commands;
6150	Of mind unrighteous, and inflexible
6150	His stubborn heart; his thoughts are all of blood;
6150	E'en as a lion, whom his mighty strength
6150	And dauntless courage lead to leap the fold,
6150	And 'mid the trembling flocks to seize his prey;
6150	E'en so Achilles hath discarded ruth,
6150	And conscience, arbiter of good and ill.
6150	A man may lose his best-lov'd friend, a son,
6150	Or his own mother's son, a brother dear:
6150	He mourns and weeps, but time his grief allays,
6150	For fate to man a patient mind hath giv'n:
6150	But godlike Hector's body, after death,
6150	Achilles, unrelenting, foully drags,
6150	Lash'd to his car, around his comrade's tomb.
6150	This is not to his praise; though brave he be,
6150	Yet thus our anger he may justly rouse,
6150	"Who in his rage insults the senseless clay."""
6150	""
6150	To whom, indignant, white-arm'd Juno thus:
6150	"""Some show of reason were there in thy speech,"
6150	God of the silver bow, could Hector boast
6150	Of equal dignity with Peleus' son.
6150	A mortal one, and nurs'd at woman's breast;
6150	The other, of a Goddess born, whom I
6150	Nurtur'd and rear'd, and to a mortal gave
6150	In marriage; gave to Peleus, best belov'd
6150	By all th' Immortals, of the race of man.
6150	Ye, Gods, attended all the marriage rites;
6150	Thou too, companion base, false friend, wast there,
6150	"And, playing on thy lyre, didst share the feast."""
6150	""
6150	To whom the Cloud-compeller answer'd thus:
6150	"""Juno, restrain thy wrath; they shall not both"
6150	Attain like honour; yet was Hector once,
6150	Of all the mortals that in Ilium dwell,
6150	Dearest to all the Gods, and chief to me;
6150	For never did he fail his gifts to bring.
6150	And with, burnt-off 'rings and libations due
6150	My altars crown; such worship I receiv'd.
6150	Yet shall bold Hector's body, not without
6150	The knowledge of Achilles, be remov'd;
6150	For day and night his Goddess-mother keeps
6150	Her constant watch beside him. Then, some God
6150	Bid Thetis hither to my presence haste;
6150	And I with prudent words will counsel her,
6150	That so Achilles may at Priam's hand
6150	"Large ransom take, and set brave Hector free."""
6150	""
6150	He said; and promptly on his errand sprang
6150	The storm-swift Iris; in the dark-blue sea
6150	She plung'd, midway 'twixt Imbros' rugged shore
6150	And Samos' isle; the parting waters plash'd.
6150	As down to ocean's lowest depths she dropp'd,
6150	Like to a plummet, which the fisherman
6150	Lets fall, encas'd in wild bull's horn, to bear
6150	Destruction to the sea's voracious tribes.
6150	There found she Thetis in a hollow cave,
6150	Around her rang'd the Ocean Goddesses:
6150	She, in the midst, was weeping o'er the fate
6150	Her matchless son awaiting, doom'd to die
6150	Far from his home, on fertile plains of Troy.
6150	Swift-footed Iris at her side appear'd,
6150	"And thus address'd her: ""Hasten, Thetis; Jove,"
6150	"Lord of immortal counsel, summons thee."""
6150	To whom the silver-footed Goddess thus:
6150	"""What would with me the mighty King of Heav'n?"
6150	Press'd as I am with grief, I am asham'd
6150	To mingle with the Gods; yet will I go:
6150	"Nor shall he speak in vain, whate'er his words."""
6150	""
6150	Thus as she spoke, her veil the Goddess took,
6150	All black, than which none deeper could be found;
6150	She rose to go; the storm-swift Iris led
6150	The way before her; ocean's parted waves
6150	Around their path receded; to the beach
6150	Ascending, upwards straight to Heav'n they sprang.
6150	Th' all-seeing son of Saturn there they found,
6150	And rang'd around him all th' immortal Gods.
6150	Pallas made way; and by the throne of Jove
6150	Sat Thetis, Juno proff'ring to her hand
6150	A goblet fair of gold, and adding words
6150	Of welcome; she the cup receiv'd, and drank.
6150	Then thus began the sire of Gods and men:
6150	"""Thou, Thetis, sorrowing to Olympus com'st,"
6150	Borne down by ceaseless grief; I know it well;
6150	Yet hear the cause for which I summon'd thee.
6150	About Achilles, thy victorious son,
6150	And valiant Hector's body, for nine days
6150	Hath contest been in Heav'n; and some have urg'd
6150	That Hermes should by stealth the corpse remove.
6150	This to Achilles' praise I mean to turn,
6150	And thus thy rev'rence and thy love retain.
6150	Then haste thee to the camp, and to thy son
6150	My message bear; tell him that all the Gods
6150	Are fill'd with wrath; and I above the rest
6150	Am angry, that beside the beaked ships,
6150	He, mad with rage, the corpse of Hector keeps:
6150	So may he fear me, and restore the dead.
6150	Iris meantime to Priam I will send,
6150	And bid him seek the Grecian ships, and there
6150	Obtain his son's release: and with him bring
6150	"Such presents as may melt Achilles' heart."""
6150	""
6150	He said; the silver-footed Queen obey'd;
6150	Down from Olympus' heights in haste she sped,
6150	And sought her son; him found she in his tent,
6150	Groaning with anguish, while his comrades round,
6150	Plying their tasks, prepar'd the morning meal.
6150	For them a goodly sheep, full-fleec'd, was slain.
6150	Close by his side his Goddess-mother stood,
6150	And gently touch'd him with her hand, and said,
6150	"""How long, my son, wilt thou thy soul consume"
6150	With grief and mourning, mindful nor of food
6150	Nor sleep? nor dost thou wisely, to abstain
6150	From woman's love; for short thy time on earth:
6150	Death and imperious fate are close at hand.
6150	Hear then my words; a messenger from Jove
6150	To thee I come, to tell thee that the Gods
6150	Are fill'd with wrath, and he above the rest
6150	Is angry, that beside the beaked ships
6150	Thou, mad with rage, the corpse of Hector keep'st.
6150	"Then ransom take, and liberate the dead."""
6150	""
6150	To whom Achilles, swift of foot, replied:
6150	"""So be it; ransom let him bring, and bear"
6150	"His dead away, if such the will of Jove."""
6150	""
6150	Thus, in the concourse of the ships, they two,
6150	Mother and son, their lengthen'd converse held.
6150	""
6150	Then Saturn's son to Iris gave command:
6150	"""Haste thee, swift Iris, from Olympus' height,"
6150	To Troy, to royal Priam bear my words;
6150	And bid him seek the Grecian ships, and there
6150	Obtain his son's release; and with him take
6150	Such presents as may melt Achilles' heart.
6150	Alone, no Trojan with him, must he go;
6150	Yet may a herald on his steps attend,
6150	Some aged man, his smoothly-rolling car
6150	And mules to drive; and to the city back
6150	To bring his dead, whom great Achilles slew.
6150	Nor let the fear of death disturb his mind:
6150	Hermes shall with him, as his escort, go,
6150	And to Achilles' presence safely bring.
6150	Arriv'd within the tent, nor he himself
6150	Will slay him, but from others will protect.
6150	Not ignorant is he, nor void of sense,
6150	Nor disobedient to the Gods' behest
6150	"But will with pitying eyes his suppliant view."""
6150	""
6150	He said; and on his errand sped in haste
6150	The storm-swift Iris; when to Priam's house
6150	She came, the sounds of wailing met her ear.
6150	Within the court, around their father, sat
6150	His sons, their raiment all bedew'd with tears;
6150	And in the midst, close cover'd with his robe,
6150	Their sire, his head and neck with dirt defil'd,
6150	Which, wallowing on the earth, himself had heap'd,
6150	With his own hands, upon his hoary head.
6150	Throughout the house his daughters loudly wail'd
6150	In mem'ry of the many and the brave
6150	Who lay in death, by Grecian warriors slain.
6150	Beside him stood the messenger of Jove,
6150	And whisper'd, while his limbs with terror shook:
6150	"""Fear nothing, Priam, son of Dardanus,"
6150	Nor let thy mind be troubled; not for ill,
6150	But here on kindly errand am I sent:
6150	To thee I come, a messenger from Jove,
6150	Who from on high looks down on thee with eyes
6150	Of pitying love; he bids thee ransom home
6150	The godlike Hector's corpse; and with thee take
6150	Such presents as may melt Achilles' heart.
6150	Alone, no Trojan with thee, must thou go;
6150	Yet may a herald on thy steps attend,
6150	Some aged man, thy smoothly-rolling car
6150	And mules to drive, and to the city back
6150	To bring thy dead, whom great Achilles slew.
6150	Nor let the fear of death disturb thy mind:
6150	Hermes shall with thee, as thine escort, go,
6150	And to Achilles' presence safely bring.
6150	Arriv'd within the tent, nor he himself
6150	Will slay thee, but from others will protect;
6150	Not ignorant is he, nor void of sense,
6150	Nor disobedient to the Gods' behest,
6150	"But will with pitying eyes his suppliant view."""
6150	""
6150	Swift-footed Iris said, and vanish'd straight:
6150	He to his sons commandment gave, the mules
6150	To yoke beneath the smoothly-rolling car,
6150	And on the axle fix the wicker seat.
6150	Himself the lofty cedar chamber sought,
6150	Fragrant, high-roof'd, with countless treasures stor'd;
6150	And call'd to Hecuba his wife, and said,
6150	"""Good wife, a messenger from Jove hath come,"
6150	Who bids me seek the Grecian ships, and there
6150	Obtain my son's release; and with me take
6150	Such presents as may melt Achilles' heart.
6150	Say then, what think'st thou? for my mind inclines
6150	"To seek the ships within the Grecian camp."""
6150	""
6150	So he; but Hecuba lamenting cried,
6150	"""Alas, alas! where are thy senses gone?"
6150	And where the wisdom, once of high repute
6150	'Mid strangers, and 'mid those o'er whom thou reign'st?
6150	How canst thou think alone to seek the ships,
6150	Ent'ring his presence, who thy sons hath slain,
6150	Many and brave? an iron heart is thine!
6150	Of that bloodthirsty and perfidious man,
6150	If thou within the sight and reach shalt come,
6150	No pity will he feel, no rev'rence show:
6150	Rather remain we here apart and mourn;
6150	For him, when at his birth his thread of life
6150	Was spun by fate, 'twas destin'd that afar
6150	From home and parents, he should glut the maw
6150	Of rav'ning dogs, by that stern warrior's tent,
6150	Whose inmost heart I would I could devour:
6150	Such for my son were adequate revenge,
6150	Whom not in ignominious flight he slew;
6150	But standing, thoughtless of escape or flight,
6150	"For Trojan men and Troy's deep-bosom'd dames."""
6150	""
6150	To whom in answer Priam, godlike sire:
6150	"""Seek not to hinder me; nor be thyself"
6150	A bird of evil omen in my house;
6150	For thou shalt not persuade me. If indeed
6150	This message had been brought by mortal man,
6150	Prophet, or seer, or sacrificing priest,
6150	I should have deem'd it false, and laugh'd to scorn
6150	The idle tale; but now (for I myself
6150	Both saw and heard the Goddess) I must go;
6150	Nor unfulfill'd shall be the words I speak:
6150	And if indeed it be my fate to die
6150	Beside the vessels of the brass-clad Greeks,
6150	I am content! by fierce Achilles' hand
6150	Let me be slain, so once more in my arms
6150	"I hold my boy, and give my sorrow vent."""
6150	Then raising up the coffer's polish'd lid,
6150	He chose twelve gorgeous shawls, twelve single cloaks.
6150	As many rugs, as many splendid robes,
6150	As many tunics; then of gold he took
6150	Ten talents full; two tripods, burnish'd bright,
6150	Four caldrons; then a cup of beauty rare,
6150	A rich possession, which the men of Thrace
6150	Had giv'n, when there he went ambassador;
6150	E'en this he spar'd not, such his keen desire
6150	His son to ransom. From the corridor
6150	With angry words he drove the Trojans all:
6150	""
6150	"""Out with ye, worthless rascals, vagabonds!"
6150	Have ye no griefs at home, that here ye come
6150	To pester me? or is it not enough
6150	That Jove with deep affliction visits me,
6150	Slaying my bravest son? ye to your cost
6150	Shall know his loss: since now that he is gone,
6150	The Greeks shall find you easier far to slay.
6150	But may my eyes be clos'd in death, ere see
6150	"The city sack'd, and utterly destroy'd."""
6150	""
6150	He said, and with his staff drove out the crowd;
6150	Before the old man's anger fled they all;
6150	Then to his sons in threat'ning tone he cried;
6150	To Paris, Helenus, and Agathon,
6150	Pammon, Antiphonus, Polites brave,
6150	Deiphobus, and bold Hippothous,
6150	And godlike Dius; all these nine with threats

6150	"""Haste, worthless sons, my scandal and my shame!"
6150	Would that ye all beside the Grecian ships
6150	In Hector's stead had died! Oh woe is me,
6150	Who have begotten sons, in all the land
6150	The best and bravest; now remains not one;
6150	Mestor, and Troilus, dauntless charioteer,
6150	And Hector, who a God 'mid men appear'd,
6150	Nor like a mortal's offspring, but a God's:
6150	All these hath Mars cut off; and left me none,
6150	None but the vile and refuse; liars all,
6150	Vain skipping coxcombs, in the dance alone,
6150	And in nought else renown'd; base plunderers,
6150	From their own countrymen, of lambs and kids.
6150	When, laggards, will ye harness me the car
6150	"Equipp'd with all things needed for the way?"""
6150	""
6150	He said; they quail'd beneath their father's wrath,
6150	And brought the smoothly-running mule-wain out,
6150	Well-fram'd, new-built; and fix'd the wicker seat;
6150	Then from the peg the mule-yoke down they took,
6150	Of boxwood wrought, with boss and rings complete;
6150	And with the yoke, the yoke-band brought they forth,
6150	Nine cubits long; and to the polish'd pole
6150	At the far end attach'd; the breast-rings then
6150	Fix'd to the pole-piece: and on either side
6150	Thrice round the knob the leathern thong they wound.
6150	And bound it fast, and inward turn'd the tongue.
6150	Then the rich ransom, from the chambers brought,
6150	Of Hector's head, upon the wain they pil'd;
6150	And yok'd the strong-hoof'd mules, to harness train'd,
6150	The Mysians' splendid present to the King:
6150	To Priam's car they harness'd then the steeds,
6150	Which he himself at polish'd manger fed.
6150	""
6150	Deep thoughts revolving, in the lofty halls
6150	Were met the herald and the aged King,
6150	When Hecuba with troubled mind drew near;
6150	In her right hand a golden cup she bore
6150	Of luscious wine, that ere they took their way
6150	They to the Gods might due libations pour;
6150	Before the car she stood, and thus she spoke:
6150	"""Take, and to father Jove thine off'ring pour,"
6150	And pray that he may bring thee safely home
6150	From all thy foes; since sore against my will
6150	Thou needs wilt venture to the ships of Greece.
6150	Then to Idaean Jove, the cloud-girt son
6150	Of Saturn, who th' expanse of Troy surveys,
6150	Prefer thy pray'r, beseeching him to send,
6150	On thy right hand, a winged messenger,
6150	The bird he loves the best, of strongest flight;
6150	That thou thyself mayst see and know the sign,
6150	And, firm in faith, approach the ships of Greece.
6150	But should all-seeing Jove the sign withhold,
6150	Then not with my consent shouldst thou attempt,
6150	"Whate'er thy wish, to reach the Grecian ships."""
6150	""
6150	To whom, in answer, godlike Priam thus:
6150	"""O woman, I refuse not to obey"
6150	Thy counsel; good it is to raise the hands
6150	"In pray'r to Heav'n, and Jove's protection seek."""
6150	The old man said; and bade th' attendant pour
6150	Pure water on his hands; with ewer she,
6150	And basin, stood beside him: from his wife,
6150	The due ablutions made, he took the cup;
6150	Then in the centre of the court he stood,
6150	And as he pour'd the wine, look'd up to Heav'n,
6150	And thus with voice uplifted pray'd aloud:
6150	"""O father Jove, who rul'st on Ida's height,"
6150	Most great, most glorious! grant that I may find
6150	Some pity in Achilles' heart; and send,
6150	On my right hand, a winged messenger,
6150	The bird thou lov'st the best, of strongest flight,
6150	That I myself may see and know the sign,
6150	"And, firm in faith, approach the ships of Greece."""
6150	""
6150	Thus as he pray'd, the Lord of counsel heard;
6150	And sent forthwith an eagle, feather'd king,
6150	Dark bird of chase, and Dusky thence surnam'd:
6150	Wide as the portals, well secur'd with bolts,
6150	That guard some wealthy monarch's lofty hall,
6150	On either side his ample pinions spread.
6150	On the right hand appear'd he, far above
6150	The city soaring; they the fav'ring sign
6150	With joy beheld, and ev'ry heart was cheer'd.
6150	Mounting his car in haste, the aged King
6150	Drove thro' the court, and thro' the echoing porch;
6150	The mules in front, by sage Idaeus driv'n,
6150	That drew the four-wheel'd wain; behind them came
6150	The horses, down the city's steep descent
6150	Urg'd by th' old man to speed; the crowd of friends
6150	That follow'd mourn'd for him, as doom'd to death.
6150	Descended from the city to the plain,
6150	His sons and sons-in-law to Ilium took
6150	Their homeward way; advancing o'er the plain
6150	They two escap'd not Jove's all-seeing eye;
6150	Pitying he saw the aged sire; and thus
6150	At once to Hermes spoke, his much-lov'd son:
6150	"""Hermes, for thou in social converse lov'st"
6150	To mix with men, and hear'st whome'er thou wilt;
6150	Haste thee, and Priam to the Grecian ships
6150	So lead, that none of all the Greeks may see
6150	"Ere at Achilles' presence he attain."""
6150	""
6150	He said; nor disobey'd the heav'nly Guide;
6150	His golden sandals on his feet he bound,
6150	Ambrosial work; which bore him o'er the waves,
6150	Swift as the wind, and o'er the wide-spread earth;
6150	Then took his rod, wherewith he seals at will
6150	The eyes of men, and wakes again from sleep.
6150	This in his hand he bore, and sprang for flight.
6150	Soon the wide Hellespont he reach'd, and Troy,
6150	And pass'd in likeness of a princely youth,
6150	In op'ning manhood, fairest term of life.
6150	""
6150	The twain had pass'd by Ilus' lofty tomb,
6150	And halted there the horses and the mules
6150	Beside the margin of the stream to drink;
6150	For darkness now was creeping o'er the earth:
6150	When through the gloom the herald Hermes saw
6150	Approaching near, to Priam thus he cried:
6150	"""O son of Dardanus, bethink thee well;"
6150	Of prudent counsel great is now our need.
6150	A man I see, and fear he means us ill.
6150	Say, with the horses shall we fly at once,
6150	"Or clasp his knees, and for his mercy sue?"""
6150	The old man heard, his mind confus'd with dread;
6150	So grievously he fear'd, that ev'ry hair
6150	Upon his bended limbs did stand on end;
6150	He stood astounded; but the Guardian-God
6150	Approach'd, and took him by the hand, and said:
6150	"""Where, father, goest thou thus with horse and mule"
6150	In the still night, when men are sunk in sleep?
6150	And fear'st thou not the slaughter-breathing Greeks,
6150	Thine unrelenting foes, and they so near?
6150	If any one of them should see thee now,
6150	So richly laden in the gloom of night,
6150	How wouldst thou feel? thou art not young thyself,
6150	And this old man, thy comrade, would avail
6150	But little to protect thee from assault.
6150	I will not harm thee, nay will shield from harm,
6150	"For like my father's is, methinks, thy face."""
6150	""
6150	To whom in answer Priam, godlike sire:
6150	"""'Tis as thou say'st, fair son; yet hath some God"
6150	Extended o'er me his protecting hand,
6150	Who sends me such a guide, so opportune.
6150	Bless'd are thy parents in a son so grac'd
6150	"In face and presence, and of mind so wise."""
6150	""
6150	To whom in answer thus the Guardian-God:
6150	"""O father, well and wisely dost thou speak;"
6150	But tell me this, and truly: dost thou bear
6150	These wealthy treasures to some foreign land,
6150	That they for thee in safety may be stor'd?
6150	Or have ye all resolv'd to fly from Troy
6150	In fear, your bravest slain, thy gallant son,
6150	"Who never from the Greeks' encounter flinch'd?"""
6150	""
6150	To whom in answer Priam, godlike sire:
6150	"""Who art thou, noble Sir, and what thy race,"
6150	"That speak'st thus fairly of my hapless son?"""
6150	""
6150	To whom in answer thus the Guardian-God:
6150	"""Try me, old man; of godlike Hector ask;"
6150	For often in the glory-giving fight
6150	These eyes have seen him; chief, when to the ships
6150	The Greeks he drove, and with the sword destroy'd.
6150	We gaz'd in wonder; from the fight restrain'd
6150	By Peleus' son, with Agamemnon wroth.
6150	His follower I; one ship convey'd us both;
6150	One of the Myrmidons I am; my sire
6150	Polyctor, rich, but aged, e'en as thou.
6150	Six sons he hath, besides myself, the sev'nth;
6150	And I by lot was drafted for the war.
6150	I from the ships am to the plain come forth;
6150	For with the dawn of day the keen-ey'd Greeks
6150	Will round the city marshal their array.
6150	They chafe in idleness; the chiefs in vain
6150	"Strive to restrain their ardour for the fight."""
6150	""
6150	To whom in answer Priam, godlike sire:
6150	"""If of Achilles, Peleus' son, thou art"
6150	Indeed a follower, tell me all the truth;
6150	Lies yet my son beside the Grecian ships,
6150	Or hath Achilles torn him limb from limb,
6150	"And to his dogs the mangled carcase giv'n?"""
6150	""
6150	To whom in answer thus the Guardian-God:
6150	"""On him, old man, nor dogs nor birds have fed,"
6150	But by the ship of Peleus' son he lies
6150	Within the tent; twelve days he there hath lain,
6150	Nor hath corruption touch'd his flesh, nor worms,
6150	That wont to prey on men in battle slain.
6150	The corpse, indeed, with each returning morn,
6150	Around his comrade's tomb Achilles drags,
6150	Yet leaves it still uninjur'd; thou thyself
6150	Mightst see how fresh, as dew-besprent, he lies,
6150	From blood-stains cleans'd, and clos'd his many wounds,
6150	For many a lance was buried in his corpse.
6150	So, e'en in death, the blessed Gods above,
6150	"Who lov'd him well, protect thy noble son."""
6150	""
6150	He said; th' old man rejoicing heard his words,
6150	"And answer'd, ""See, my son, how good it is"
6150	To give th' immortal Gods their tribute due;
6150	For never did my son, while yet he liv'd,
6150	Neglect the Gods who on Olympus dwell;
6150	And thence have they remember'd him in death.
6150	Accept, I pray, this goblet rich-emboss'd;
6150	Be thou my guard, and, under Heav'n, my guide,
6150	"Until I reach the tent of Peleus' son."""
6150	""
6150	To whom in answer thus the Guardian-God:
6150	"""Old father, me thy younger wouldst thou tempt,"
6150	In vain; who bidd'st me at thy hands accept
6150	Thy proffer'd presents, to Achilles' wrong.
6150	I dread his anger; and should hold it shame
6150	To plunder him, through fear of future ill.
6150	But, as thy guide, I could conduct thee safe,
6150	As far as Argos, journeying by thy side,
6150	On ship-board or on foot; nor by the fault
6150	"Of thy conductor shouldst thou meet with harm."""
6150	""
6150	Thus spoke the Guardian-God, and on the car
6150	Mounting in haste, he took the whip and reins,
6150	And with fresh vigour mules and horses fill'd.
6150	When to the ship-tow'rs and the trench they came,
6150	The guard had late been busied with their meal;
6150	And with deep sleep the heav'nly Guide o'erspread
6150	The eyes of all; then open'd wide the gates,
6150	And push'd aside the bolts, and led within
6150	Both Priam, and the treasure-laden wain.
6150	But when they reach'd Achilles' lofty tent,
6150	(Which for their King the Myrmidons had built
6150	Of fir-trees fell'd, and overlaid the roof
6150	With rushes mown from off the neighb'ring mead;
6150	And all around a spacious court enclos'd
6150	With cross-set palisades; a single bar
6150	Of fir the gateway guarded, which to shut
6150	Three men, of all the others, scarce suffic'd,
6150	And three to open; but Achilles' hand
6150	Unaided shut with ease the massive bar)
6150	Then for the old man Hermes op'd the gate,
6150	And brought within the court the gifts design'd
6150	For Peleus' godlike son; then from the car
6150	Sprang to the ground, and thus to Priam spoke:
6150	"""Old man, a God hath hither been thy guide;"
6150	Hermes I am, and sent to thee from Jove,
6150	Father of all, to bring thee safely here.
6150	I now return, nor to Achilles' eyes
6150	Will I appear; beseems it not a God
6150	To greet a mortal in the sight of all.
6150	But go thou in, and clasp Achilles' knees,
6150	And supplicate him for his father's sake,
6150	His fair-hair'd mother's, and his child's, that so
6150	"Thy words may stir an answer in his heart."""
6150	""
6150	Thus saying, Hermes to Olympus' heights
6150	Return'd; and Priam from his chariot sprang,
6150	And left Idaeus there, in charge to keep
6150	The horses and the mules, while he himself
6150	Enter'd the dwelling straight, where wont to sit
6150	Achilles, lov'd of Heav'n. The chief he found
6150	Within, his followers seated all apart;
6150	Two only in his presence minister'd,
6150	The brave Automedon, and Alcimus,
6150	A warrior bold; scarce ended the repast
6150	Of food and wine; the table still was set.
6150	Great Priam enter'd, unperceiv'd of all;
6150	And standing by Achilles, with his arms
6150	Embrac'd his knees, and kiss'd those fearful hands,
6150	Blood-stain'd, which many of his sons had slain.
6150	As when a man, by cruel fate pursued,
6150	In his own land hath shed another's blood,
6150	And flying, seeks beneath some wealthy house
6150	A foreign refuge; wond'ring, all behold:
6150	On godlike Priam so with wonder gaz'd
6150	Achilles; wonder seiz'd th' attendants all,
6150	And one to other looked; then Priam thus
6150	To Peleus' son his suppliant speech address'd:
6150	"""Think, great Achilles, rival of the Gods,"
6150	Upon thy father, e'en as I myself
6150	Upon the threshold of unjoyous age:
6150	And haply he, from them that dwell around
6150	May suffer wrong, with no protector near
6150	To give him aid; yet he, rejoicing, knows
6150	That thou still liv'st; and day by day may hope
6150	To see his son returning safe from Troy;
6150	While I, all hapless, that have many sons,
6150	The best and bravest through the breadth of Troy,
6150	Begotten, deem that none are left me now.
6150	Fifty there were, when came the sons of Greece;
6150	Nineteen the offspring of a single womb;
6150	The rest, the women of my household bore.
6150	Of these have many by relentless Mars
6150	Been laid in dust; but he, my only one,
6150	The city's and his brethren's sole defence,
6150	He, bravely fighting in his country's cause,
6150	Hector, but lately by thy hand hath fall'n:
6150	On his behalf I venture to approach
6150	The Grecian ships; for his release to thee
6150	To make my pray'r, and priceless ransom pay.
6150	Then thou, Achilles, reverence the Gods;
6150	And, for thy father's sake, look pitying down
6150	On me, more needing pity; since I bear
6150	Such grief as never man on earth hath borne.
6150	"Who stoop to kiss the hand that slew my son."""
6150	""
6150	Thus as he spoke, within Achilles' breast
6150	Fond mem'ry of his father rose; he touch'd
6150	The old man's hand, and gently put him by;
6150	Then wept they both, by various mem'ries stirr'd:
6150	One, prostrate at Achilles' feet, bewail'd
6150	His warrior son; Achilles for his sire,
6150	And for Patroclus wept, his comrade dear;
6150	And through the house their weeping loud was heard.
6150	But when Achilles had indulg'd his grief,
6150	And eas'd the yearning of his heart and limbs,
6150	Uprising, with his hand the aged sire,
6150	Pitying his hoary head and hoary beard,
6150	He rais'd, and thus with gentle words address'd:
6150	""
6150	"""Alas, what sorrows, poor old man, are thine!"
6150	How couldst thou venture to the Grecian ships
6150	Alone, and to the presence of the man
6150	Whose hand hath slain so many of thy sons,
6150	Many and brave? an iron heart is thine!
6150	But sit thou on this seat; and in our hearts,
6150	Though filled with grief, let us that grief suppress;
6150	For woful lamentation nought avails.
6150	Such, is the thread the Gods for mortals spin,
6150	To live in woe, while they from cares are free.
6150	Two coffers lie beside the door of Jove,
6150	With gifts for man: one good, the other ill;
6150	To whom from each the Lord of lightning gives,
6150	Him sometimes evil, sometimes good befalls;
6150	To whom the ill alone, him foul disgrace
6150	And grinding mis'ry o'er the earth pursue:
6150	By God and man alike despis'd he roams.
6150	Thus from his birth the Gods to Peleus gave
6150	Excellent gifts; with wealth and substance bless'd
6150	Above his fellows; o'er the Myrmidons
6150	He rul'd with sov'reign sway; and Heav'n bestow'd
6150	On him, a mortal, an immortal bride.
6150	Yet this of ill was mingled in his lot,
6150	That in his house no rising race he saw
6150	Of future Kings; one only son he had,
6150	One doom'd to early death; nor is it mine
6150	To tend my father's age; but far from home
6150	Thee and thy sons in Troy I vex with war.
6150	Much have we heard too of thy former wealth;
6150	Above what Lesbos northward, Macar's seat,
6150	Contains, and Upper Phrygia, and the shores
6150	Of boundless Hellespont, 'tis said that thou
6150	In wealth and number of thy sons wast bless'd.
6150	But since on thee this curse the Gods have brought,
6150	Still round thy city war and murder rage.
6150	Bear up, nor thus with grief incessant mourn;
6150	Vain is thy sorrow for thy gallant son;
6150	"Thou canst not raise him, and mayst suffer more."""
6150	""
6150	To whom in answer Priam, godlike sire;
6150	"""Tell me not yet, illustrious chief, to sit,"
6150	While Hector lies, uncar'd for, in the tent;
6150	But let me quickly go, that with mine eyes
6150	I may behold my son; and thou accept
6150	The ample treasures which we tender thee:
6150	Mayst thou enjoy them, and in safety reach
6150	Thy native land, since thou hast spar'd my life,
6150	"And bidd'st me still behold the light of Heav'n."""
6150	""
6150	To whom Achilles thus with stern regard:
6150	"""Old man, incense me not; I mean myself"
6150	To give thee back thy son; for here of late
6150	Despatch'd by Jove, my Goddess-mother came,
6150	The daughter of the aged Ocean-God:
6150	And thee too, Priam, well I know, some God
6150	(I cannot err) hath guided to our ships.
6150	No mortal, though in vent'rous youth, would dare
6150	Our camp to enter; nor could hope to pass
6150	Unnotic'd by the watch, nor easily
6150	Remove the pond'rous bar that guards our doors.
6150	But stir not up my anger in my grief;
6150	Lest, suppliant though thou be, within my tent
6150	"I brook thee not, and Jove's command transgress."""
6150	""
6150	He said; the old man trembled, and obey'd;
6150	Then to the door-way, with a lion's spring,
6150	Achilles rush'd; not unaccompanied;
6150	With him Automedon and Aleimus,
6150	His two attendants, of his followers all,
6150	Next to the lost Patroclus, best-esteem'd;
6150	They from the yoke the mules and horses loos'd;
6150	Then led the herald of the old man in,
6150	And bade him sit; and from the polish'd wain
6150	The costly ransom took of Hector's head.
6150	Two robes they left, and one well-woven vest,
6150	To clothe the corpse, and send with honour home.
6150	Then to the female slaves he gave command
6150	To wash the body, and anoint with oil,
6150	Apart, that Priam might not see his son;
6150	Lest his griev'd heart its passion unrestrain'd
6150	Should utter, and Achilles, rous'd to wrath,
6150	His suppliant slay, and Jove's command transgress.
6150	When they had wash'd the body, and with oil
6150	Anointed, and around it wrapp'd the robe
6150	And vest, Achilles lifted up the dead
6150	With his own hands, and laid him on the couch;
6150	Which to the polish'd wain his followers rais'd.
6150	Then groaning, on his friend by name he call'd:
6150	"""Forgive, Patroclus! be not wroth with me,"
6150	If in the realm of darkness thou shouldst hear
6150	That godlike Hector to his father's arms,
6150	For no mean ransom, I restore; whereof
6150	"A fitting share for thee I set aside."""
6150	""
6150	This said, Achilles to the tent return'd;
6150	On the carv'd couch, from whence he rose, he sat
6150	Beside the wall; and thus to Priam spoke:
6150	""
6150	"""Old man, thy son, according to thy pray'r,"
6150	Is giv'n thee back; upon the couch he lies;
6150	Thyself shalt see him at the dawn of day.
6150	Meanwhile the ev'ning meal demands our care.
6150	Not fair-hair'd Niobe abstain'd from food
6150	When in the house her children lay in death,
6150	Six beauteous daughters and six stalwart sons.
6150	The youths, Apollo with his silver bow,
6150	The maids, the Archer-Queen, Diana, slew,
6150	With anger fill'd that Niobe presum'd
6150	Herself with fair Latona to compare,
6150	Her many children with her rival's two;
6150	So by the two were all the many slain.
6150	Nine days in death they lay; and none was there
6150	To pay their fun'ral rites; for Saturn's son
6150	Had given to all the people hearts of stone.
6150	At length th' immortal Gods entomb'd the dead.
6150	Nor yet did Niobe, when now her grief
6150	Had worn itself in tears, from food refrain.
6150	And now in Sipylus, amid the rocks,
6150	And lonely mountains, where the Goddess nymphs
6150	That love to dance by Achelous' stream,
6150	'Tis said, were cradled, she, though turn'd to stone,
6150	Broods o'er the wrongs inflicted by the Gods.
6150	So we too, godlike sire, the meal may share;
6150	And later, thou thy noble son mayst mourn,
6150	"To Troy restor'd--well worthy he thy tears."""
6150	""
6150	This said, he slaughter'd straight a white-fleec'd sheep;
6150	His comrades then the carcase flay'd and dress'd:
6150	The meat prepar'd, and fasten'd to the spits;
6150	Roasted with care, and from the fire withdrew.
6150	The bread Automedon from baskets fair
6150	Apportion'd out; the meat Achilles shar'd.
6150	They on the viands set before them fell.
6150	The rage of thirst and hunger satisfied,
6150	In wonder Priam on Achilles gaz'd,
6150	His form and stature; as a God he seem'd;
6150	And he too look'd on Priam, and admir'd
6150	His venerable face, and gracious speech.
6150	With mutual pleasure each on other gaz'd,
6150	Till godlike Priam first address'd his host:
6150	""
6150	"""Dismiss me now, illustrious chief, to rest;"
6150	And lie we down, in gentle slumbers wrapp'd;
6150	For never have mine eyes been clos'd in sleep,
6150	Since by thy hand my gallant son was slain:
6150	But groaning still, I brood upon my woes,
6150	And in my court with dust my head defile.
6150	Now have I tasted bread, now ruddy wine
6150	"Hath o'er my palate pass'd; but not till now."""
6150	""
6150	Thus he; his comrades and th' attendant maids
6150	Achilles order'd in the corridor
6150	Two mattresses to place, with blankets fair
6150	Of purple wool o'erlaid; and on the top
6150	Rugs and soft sheets for upper cov'ring spread.
6150	They from the chamber, torch in hand, withdrew,
6150	And with obedient haste two beds prepar'd.
6150	Then thus Achilles spoke in jesting tone:
6150	"""Thou needs must sleep without, my good old friend;"
6150	Lest any leader of the Greeks should come,
6150	As is their custom, to confer with me;
6150	Of them whoe'er should find thee here by night
6150	Forthwith to Agamemnon would report,
6150	And Hector might not be so soon, restor'd.
6150	But tell me truly this; how many days
6150	For godlike Hector's fun'ral rites ye need;
6150	That for so long a time I may myself
6150	"Refrain from combat, and the people stay."""
6150	""
6150	To whom in answer Priam, godlike sire:
6150	"""If by thy leave we may indeed perform"
6150	His fun'ral rites, to thee, Achilles, great
6150	Will be our gratitude, if this thou grant.
6150	Thou know'st how close the town is hemm'd around;
6150	And from the mountain, distant as it is,
6150	The Trojans well may fear to draw the wood.
6150	Nine days to public mourning would we give;
6150	The tenth, to fun'ral rites and fun'ral feast;
6150	Then on th' eleventh would we raise his mound;
6150	"The twelfth, renew the war, if needs we must."""
6150	""
6150	To whom Achilles swift of foot replied:
6150	"""So shall it be, old Priam; I engage"
6150	"To stay the battle for the time requir'd."""
6150	""
6150	Thus speaking, with his hand the old man's wrist
6150	He grasp'd, in token that he need not fear.
6150	Then in the corridor lay down to rest
6150	Old Priam and the herald, Elders sage;
6150	While in his tent's recess Achilles slept,
6150	The fair Briseis resting by his side.
6150	""
6150	In night-long slumbers lay the other Gods,
6150	And helmed chiefs, by gentle sleep subdued;
6150	But on the eyes of Hermes, Guardian-God,
6150	No slumber fell, deep pond'ring in his mind
6150	How from the ships in safety to conduct
6150	The royal Priam, and the guard elude.
6150	Above the sleeper's head he stood, and cried:
6150	"""Old man, small heed thou tak'st of coining ill,"
6150	Who, when Achilles gives thee leave to go,
6150	Sleep'st undisturb'd, surrounded by thy foes.
6150	Thy son hath been restor'd, and thou hast paid
6150	A gen'rous price; but to redeem thy life,
6150	If Agamemnon and the other Greeks
6150	Should know that thou art here, full thrice so much
6150	"Thy sons, who yet are left, would have to pay."""
6150	""
6150	He said; the old man trembled, and arous'd
6150	The herald; while the horses and the mules
6150	Were yok'd by Hermes, who with silent speed
6150	Drove through th' encampment, unobserv'd of all.
6150	But when they came to eddying Xanthus' ford,
6150	Fair-flowing stream, born of immortal Jove,
6150	To high Olympus Hermes took his flight,
6150	As morn, in saffron robe, o'er all the earth
6150	Was light diffusing; they with fun'ral wail
6150	Drove cityward the horses; following came
6150	The mules that drew the litter of the dead.
6150	The plain they travers'd o'er, observ'd of none,
6150	Or man or woman, till Cassandra, fair
6150	As golden Venus, from the topmost height
6150	Of Pergamus, her father in his car
6150	Upstanding saw, the herald at his side.
6150	Him too she saw, who on the litter lay;
6150	Then lifted up her voice, and cried aloud
6150	"To all the city, ""Hither, Trojans, come,"
6150	Both men and women, Hector see restor'd;
6150	If, while he liv'd, returning from the fight,
6150	Ye met him e'er rejoicing, who indeed
6150	"Was all the city's chiefest joy and pride."""
6150	""
6150	She said; nor man nor woman then was left
6150	Within the city; o'er the minds of all
6150	Grief pass'd, resistless; to the gates in throngs
6150	They press'd, to crowd round him who brought the dead.
6150	The first to clasp the body were his wife
6150	And honour'd mother; eagerly they sprang
6150	On the smooth-rolling wain, to touch the head
6150	Of Hector; round them, weeping, stood the crowd
6150	Weeping, till sunset, all the live-long day
6150	Had they before the gates for Hector mourn'd;
6150	Had not old Priam from the car address'd
6150	"The crowd: ""Make way, that so the mules may pass;"
6150	When to my house I shall have brought my dead,
6150	"Ye there may vent your sorrow as ye will."""
6150	""
6150	Thus as he spoke, obedient to his word
6150	They stood aside, and for the car made way:
6150	But when to Priam's lordly house they came,
6150	They laid him on a rich-wrought couch, and call'd
6150	The minstrels in, who by the hero's bed
6150	Should lead the melancholy chorus; they
6150	Pour'd forth the music of the mournful dirge,
6150	While women's voices join'd in loud lament.
6150	White-arm'd Andromache the wail began,
6150	The head of Hector clasping in her hands:
6150	"""My husband, thou art gone in pride of youth,"
6150	And in thine house hast left me desolate;
6150	Thy child an infant still, thy child and mine,
6150	Unhappy parents both! nor dare I hope
6150	That he may reach the ripeness of his youth;
6150	For ere that day shall Troy in ruin fall,
6150	Since thou art gone, her guardian! thou whose arm
6150	Defended her, her wives, and helpless babes!
6150	They now shall shortly o'er the sea be borne,
6150	And with them I shall go; thou too, my child,
6150	Must follow me, to servile labour doom'd,
6150	The suff'ring victim of a tyrant Lord;
6150	Unless perchance some angry Greek may seize
6150	And dash thee from the tow'r--a woful death!
6150	Whose brother, or whose father, or whose son
6150	By Hector hath been slain; for many a Greek
6150	By Hector's hand hath bit the bloody dust;
6150	Not light in battle was thy father's hand!
6150	Therefore for him the gen'ral city mourns;
6150	Thou to thy parents bitter grief hast caus'd,
6150	Hector! but bitt'rest grief of all hast left
6150	To me! for not to me was giv'n to clasp
6150	The hand extended from thy dying bed,
6150	Nor words of wisdom catch, which night and day,
6150	"With tears, I might have treasur'd in my heart."""
6150	""
6150	Weeping she spoke--the women join'd the wail.
6150	Then Hecuba took up the loud lament:
6150	"""Hector, of all my children dearest thou!"
6150	Dear to th' Immortals too in life wast thou,
6150	And they in death have borne thee still in mind;
6150	For other of my sons, his captives made,
6150	Across the wat'ry waste, to Samos' isle
6150	Or Imbros, or th' inhospitable shore
6150	Of Lemnos, hath Achilles, swift of foot,
6150	To slav'ry sold; thee, when his sharp-edg'd spear
6150	Had robb'd thee of thy life, he dragg'd indeed
6150	Around Patroclus' tomb, his comrade dear,
6150	Whom thou hadst slain; yet so he rais'd not up
6150	Ilis dead to life again; now liest thou here,
6150	All fresh and fair, as dew-besprent; like one
6150	Whom bright Apollo, with his arrows keen,
6150	"God of the silver bow, hath newly slain."""
6150	""
6150	Weeping, she spoke; and rous'd the gen'ral grief.
6150	Then Helen, third, the mournful strain renew'd:
6150	"""Hector, of all my brethren dearest thou!"
6150	True, godlike Paris claims me as his wife,
6150	Who bore me hither--would I then had died!
6150	But twenty years have pass'd since here I came,
6150	And left my native land; yet ne'er from thee
6150	I heard one scornful, one degrading word;
6150	And when from others I have borne reproach,
6150	Thy brothers, sisters, or thy brothers' wives,
6150	Or mother, (for thy sire was ever kind
6150	E'en as a father) thou hast check'd them still
6150	With tender feeling, and with gentle words.
6150	For thee I weep, and for myself no less:
6150	For, through the breadth of Troy, none love me now,
6150	"None kindly look on me, but all abhor."""
6150	""
6150	Weeping she spoke, and with her wept the crowd.
6150	At length the aged Priam gave command:
6150	"""Haste now, ye Trojans, to the city bring"
6150	Good store of fuel; fear no treach'rous wile;
6150	For when he sent me from the dark-ribb'd ships,
6150	Achilles promis'd that from hostile arms
6150	"Till the twelfth morn we should no harm sustain."""
6150	""
6150	He said; and they the oxen and the mules
6150	Yok'd to the wains, and from the city throng'd:
6150	Nine days they labour'd, and brought back to Troy
6150	Good store of wood; but when the tenth day's light
6150	Upon the earth appear'd, weeping, they bore
6150	Brave Hector out; and on the fun'ral pile
6150	Laying the glorious dead, applied the torch.
6150	""
6150	While yet the rosy-finger'd morn was young
6150	Round noble Hector's pyre the people press'd:
6150	When all were gather'd round, and closely throng'd
6150	First on the burning mass, as far as spread
6150	The range of fire, they pour'd the ruddy wine,
6150	And quench'd the flames: his brethren then and friends
6150	Weeping, the hot tears flowing down their cheeks,
6150	Collected from the pile the whiten'd bones;
6150	These in a golden casket they enclos'd,
6150	And o'er it spread soft shawls of purple dye;
6150	Then in a grave they laid it, and in haste
6150	With stone in pond'rous masses cover'd o'er;
6150	And rais'd a mound, and watch'd on ev'ry side,
6150	From sudden inroad of the Greeks to guard.
6150	The mound erected, back they turn'd; and all
6150	Assembled duly, shar'd the solemn feast
6150	In Priam's palace, Heav'n-descended King.
6150	""
6150	Such were the rites to glorious Hector paid.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	END OF VOLUME II.
6150	""
6150	THE END.

6150	FOOTNOTES
6150	""
6150	[1]
6150	""
6150	The text of the original leaves it somewhat in doubt whether
6150	the anger of the Greeks were directed against Thersites or Agamemnon. I
6150	believe the preponderance of authority, ancient and modern, is in
6150	favour of the former interpretation; but the latter is not without the
6150	support of some eminent scholars, and after much consideration I have
6150	been induced to adopt it. The original represents the Greeks as filled
6150	with anger _and resentment_ against _some one._ Thersites was an
6150	object of general contempt, but he had done nothing to excite those
6150	feelings: indeed, apart from the offensiveness of his tone, the public
6150	sympathy was with him; for the army was deeply dissatisfied, and
6150	resented the conduct of Agamemnon against Achilles, mainly perhaps
6150	because they had ceased to be enriched with the plunder of his
6150	successful forays (see i. 202, and ix. 387). This dissatisfaction and
6150	resentment are referred to by Neptune (xiii. 126), and by Agamemnon
6150	himself (xiv. 55). They had lately manifested themselves in the
6150	alacrity with which the whole army had caught at the insidious
6150	suggestion of abandoning the war; and, just before the second assembly,
6150	Thersites avails himself of the general feeling, constituting himself
6150	the representative of a popular grievance, to vent his personal spite
6150	against Agamemnon. Ulysses saw how dangerous such a display might be at
6150	such a moment; and artfully assuming (line 281) that the feeling was
6150	confined to Thersites alone (though in his subsequent speech, line 335,
6150	he admits and excuses the general discontent), he proceeds to cut short
6150	its expression by summary chastisement. Thereupon the fickle multitude,
6150	"""despite their anger"" (against Agamemnon), cannot refrain from laughing"
6150	at the signal discomfiture of their self-constituted champion.
6150	""
6150	This view is very fully set forth in a note on the passage appended to
6150	a translation of the Iliad by Mr. Barter, published in 1859, but which
6150	I have only seen since the publication of this work.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	[2]
6150	""
6150	See also Book xxii. l. 252.
6150	Milton, in the corresponding passage at the close of the 4th Book
6150	of 'Paradise Lost,' reverses the sign, and represents the scale of the
6150	"vanquished as ""flying up"" and ""kicking the beam."""
6150	"           ""The Fiend look'd up, and knew"
6150	    His mounted scale aloft; nor more, but fled
6150	"    Murm'ring, and with him fled the shades of night.""]"
6150	""
6150	""

6150	"the ""space interposed,"" which cannot be estimated without knowing the"
6150	total distance within which the faster was to outstrip the slower
6150	team.

6150	time before the death of his son See Book V., l. 656.

6150	Line 45 et seqq. I hope I may be pardoned for having
6150	somewhat curtailed the list of these ladies, which in the original
6150	extends over ten lines of names only. In doing so, I have followed the
6150	example of Virgil, who represents the same ladies [G. 4. 336] in
6150	attendance on Cyrene; and has not only reduced the list, but added some
6150	slight touches illustrating their occupations and private history: a
6150	liberty permissible to an imitator, but not to a translator.]

6150	L. 151. Chthizos, yesterday. But either the word must have a
6150	more extended signification than is usually given to it, or Homer must
6150	here have fallen into an error; for two complete nights and one day,
6150	that on which Patroclus met his death, had intervened since the visit
6150	of Ajax and Ulysses to the tent of Achilles. See also l. 215.

6150	""
6150	L. 547. The terms made use of in this line, and in 481, may
6150	appear somewhat coarse, as addressed by one Goddess to another: but I
6150	assure the English reader that in this passage especially I have
6150	greatly softened down the expression of the original; a literal
6150	translation of which, however forcible, would shock even the least
6150	fastidious critic. It must, indeed, be admitted that the mode in which
6150	"""the white-armed Goddess"" proceeds to execute her threat is hardly more"
6150	dignified than the language, in which it is conveyed, is refined.
6150	""
6150	""
6150	[8]
6150	""
6150	Line 737.--They being two, while I was only one. Such I
6150	believe to be the true interpretation of this passage, which, however,
6150	is one of admitted difficulty. According to our modern notions, it is
6150	not very evident what advantage two men in a car would have over one in
6150	another; nor what would be gained by the division of labour which
6150	assigned the reins to one and the whip to the other; but such, from
6150	line 740-741, appears to have been the view taken by Homer.
